94𓀴
95𓀴How these papers have been placed in sequence will be made manifest in
96𓀴the reading of them. All needless matters have been eliminated, so that
97𓀴a history almost at variance with the possibilities of later-day belief
98𓀴may stand forth as simple fact. There is throughout no statement of
99𓀴past things wherein memory may err, for all the records chosen are
100𓀴exactly contemporary, given from the standpoints and within the range
101𓀴of knowledge of those who made them.
102𓀴
103𓀴
104𓀴
105𓀴
106𓀴DRACULA
107𓀴
108𓀴
109𓀴
110𓀴
111𓀴CHAPTER I
112𓀴
113𓀴JONATHAN HARKER’S JOURNAL
114𓀴
115𓀴(_Kept in shorthand._)
116𓀴
117𓀴
118𓀴_3 May. Bistritz._--Left Munich at 8:35 P. M., on 1st May, arriving at
119𓀴Vienna early next morning; should have arrived at 6:46, but train was an
120𓀴hour late. Buda-Pesth seems a wonderful place, from the glimpse which I
121𓀴got of it from the train and the little I could walk through the
122𓀴streets. I feared to go very far from the station, as we had arrived
123𓀴late and would start as near the correct time as possible. The
124𓀴impression I had was that we were leaving the West and entering the
125𓀴East; the most western of splendid bridges over the Danube, which is
126𓀴here of noble width and depth, took us among the traditions of Turkish
127𓀴rule.
128𓀴
129𓀴We left in pretty good time, and came after nightfall to Klausenburgh.
130𓀴Here I stopped for the night at the Hotel Royale. I had for dinner, or
131𓀴rather supper, a chicken done up some way with red pepper, which was
132𓀴very good but thirsty. (_Mem._, get recipe for Mina.) I asked the
133𓀴waiter, and he said it was called “paprika hendl,” and that, as it was a
134𓀴national dish, I should be able to get it anywhere along the
135𓀴Carpathians. I found my smattering of German very useful here; indeed, I
136𓀴don’t know how I should be able to get on without it.
137𓀴
138𓀴Having had some time at my disposal when in London, I had visited the
139𓀴British Museum, and made search among the books and maps in the library
140𓀴regarding Transylvania; it had struck me that some foreknowledge of the
141𓀴country could hardly fail to have some importance in dealing with a
142𓀴nobleman of that country. I find that the district he named is in the
143𓀴extreme east of the country, just on the borders of three states,
144𓀴Transylvania, Moldavia and Bukovina, in the midst of the Carpathian
145𓀴mountains; one of the wildest and least known portions of Europe. I was
146𓀴not able to light on any map or work giving the exact locality of the
147𓀴Castle Dracula, as there are no maps of this country as yet to compare
148𓀴with our own Ordnance Survey maps; but I found that Bistritz, the post
149𓀴town named by Count Dracula, is a fairly well-known place. I shall enter
150𓀴here some of my notes, as they may refresh my memory when I talk over my
151𓀴travels with Mina.
152𓀴
153𓀴In the population of Transylvania there are four distinct nationalities:
154𓀴Saxons in the South, and mixed with them the Wallachs, who are the
155𓀴descendants of the Dacians; Magyars in the West, and Szekelys in the
156𓀴East and North. I am going among the latter, who claim to be descended
157𓀴from Attila and the Huns. This may be so, for when the Magyars conquered
158𓀴the country in the eleventh century they found the Huns settled in it. I
159𓀴read that every known superstition in the world is gathered into the
160𓀴horseshoe of the Carpathians, as if it were the centre of some sort of
161𓀴imaginative whirlpool; if so my stay may be very interesting. (_Mem._, I
162𓀴must ask the Count all about them.)
163𓀴
164𓀴I did not sleep well, though my bed was comfortable enough, for I had
165𓀴all sorts of queer dreams. There was a dog howling all night under my
166𓀴window, which may have had something to do with it; or it may have been
167𓀴the paprika, for I had to drink up all the water in my carafe, and was
168𓀴still thirsty. Towards morning I slept and was wakened by the continuous
169𓀴knocking at my door, so I guess I must have been sleeping soundly then.
170𓀴I had for breakfast more paprika, and a sort of porridge of maize flour
171𓀴which they said was “mamaliga,” and egg-plant stuffed with forcemeat, a
172𓀴very excellent dish, which they call “impletata.” (_Mem._, get recipe
173𓀴for this also.) I had to hurry breakfast, for the train started a little
174𓀴before eight, or rather it ought to have done so, for after rushing to
175𓀴the station at 7:30 I had to sit in the carriage for more than an hour
176𓀴before we began to move. It seems to me that the further east you go the
177𓀴more unpunctual are the trains. What ought they to be in China?
178𓀴
179𓀴All day long we seemed to dawdle through a country which was full of
180𓀴beauty of every kind. Sometimes we saw little towns or castles on the
181𓀴top of steep hills such as we see in old missals; sometimes we ran by
182𓀴rivers and streams which seemed from the wide stony margin on each side
183𓀴of them to be subject to great floods. It takes a lot of water, and
184𓀴running strong, to sweep the outside edge of a river clear. At every
185𓀴station there were groups of people, sometimes crowds, and in all sorts
186𓀴of attire. Some of them were just like the peasants at home or those I
187𓀴saw coming through France and Germany, with short jackets and round hats
188𓀴and home-made trousers; but others were very picturesque. The women
189𓀴looked pretty, except when you got near them, but they were very clumsy
190𓀴about the waist. They had all full white sleeves of some kind or other,
191𓀴and most of them had big belts with a lot of strips of something
192𓀴fluttering from them like the dresses in a ballet, but of course there
193𓀴were petticoats under them. The strangest figures we saw were the
194𓀴Slovaks, who were more barbarian than the rest, with their big cow-boy
195𓀴hats, great baggy dirty-white trousers, white linen shirts, and enormous
196𓀴heavy leather belts, nearly a foot wide, all studded over with brass
197𓀴nails. They wore high boots, with their trousers tucked into them, and
198𓀴had long black hair and heavy black moustaches. They are very
199𓀴picturesque, but do not look prepossessing. On the stage they would be
200𓀴set down at once as some old Oriental band of brigands. They are,
201𓀴however, I am told, very harmless and rather wanting in natural
202𓀴self-assertion.
203𓀴
204𓀴It was on the dark side of twilight when we got to Bistritz, which is a
205𓀴very interesting old place. Being practically on the frontier--for the
206𓀴Borgo Pass leads from it into Bukovina--it has had a very stormy
207𓀴existence, and it certainly shows marks of it. Fifty years ago a series
208𓀴of great fires took place, which made terrible havoc on five separate
209𓀴occasions. At the very beginning of the seventeenth century it underwent
210𓀴a siege of three weeks and lost 13,000 people, the casualties of war
211𓀴proper being assisted by famine and disease.
212𓀴
213𓀴Count Dracula had directed me to go to the Golden Krone Hotel, which I
214𓀴found, to my great delight, to be thoroughly old-fashioned, for of
215𓀴course I wanted to see all I could of the ways of the country. I was
216𓀴evidently expected, for when I got near the door I faced a
217𓀴cheery-looking elderly woman in the usual peasant dress--white
218𓀴undergarment with long double apron, front, and back, of coloured stuff
219𓀴fitting almost too tight for modesty. When I came close she bowed and
220𓀴said, “The Herr Englishman?” “Yes,” I said, “Jonathan Harker.” She
221𓀴smiled, and gave some message to an elderly man in white shirt-sleeves,
222𓀴who had followed her to the door. He went, but immediately returned with
223𓀴a letter:--
224𓀴
225𓀴     “My Friend.--Welcome to the Carpathians. I am anxiously expecting
226𓀴     you. Sleep well to-night. At three to-morrow the diligence will
227𓀴     start for Bukovina; a place on it is kept for you. At the Borgo
228𓀴     Pass my carriage will await you and will bring you to me. I trust
229𓀴     that your journey from London has been a happy one, and that you
230𓀴     will enjoy your stay in my beautiful land.
231𓀴
232𓀴“Your friend,
233𓀴
234𓀴“DRACULA.”
235𓀴
236𓀴
237𓀴_4 May._--I found that my landlord had got a letter from the Count,
238𓀴directing him to secure the best place on the coach for me; but on
239𓀴making inquiries as to details he seemed somewhat reticent, and
240𓀴pretended that he could not understand my German. This could not be
241𓀴true, because up to then he had understood it perfectly; at least, he
242𓀴answered my questions exactly as if he did. He and his wife, the old
243𓀴lady who had received me, looked at each other in a frightened sort of
244𓀴way. He mumbled out that the money had been sent in a letter, and that
245𓀴was all he knew. When I asked him if he knew Count Dracula, and could
246𓀴tell me anything of his castle, both he and his wife crossed themselves,
247𓀴and, saying that they knew nothing at all, simply refused to speak
248𓀴further. It was so near the time of starting that I had no time to ask
249𓀴any one else, for it was all very mysterious and not by any means
250𓀴comforting.
251𓀴
252𓀴Just before I was leaving, the old lady came up to my room and said in a
253𓀴very hysterical way:
254𓀴
255𓀴“Must you go? Oh! young Herr, must you go?” She was in such an excited
256𓀴state that she seemed to have lost her grip of what German she knew, and
257𓀴mixed it all up with some other language which I did not know at all. I
258𓀴was just able to follow her by asking many questions. When I told her
259𓀴that I must go at once, and that I was engaged on important business,
260𓀴she asked again:
261𓀴
262𓀴“Do you know what day it is?” I answered that it was the fourth of May.
263𓀴She shook her head as she said again:
264𓀴
265𓀴“Oh, yes! I know that! I know that, but do you know what day it is?” On
266𓀴my saying that I did not understand, she went on:
267𓀴
268𓀴“It is the eve of St. George’s Day. Do you not know that to-night, when
269𓀴the clock strikes midnight, all the evil things in the world will have
270𓀴full sway? Do you know where you are going, and what you are going to?”
271𓀴She was in such evident distress that I tried to comfort her, but
272𓀴without effect. Finally she went down on her knees and implored me not
273𓀴to go; at least to wait a day or two before starting. It was all very
274𓀴ridiculous but I did not feel comfortable. However, there was business
275𓀴to be done, and I could allow nothing to interfere with it. I therefore
276𓀴tried to raise her up, and said, as gravely as I could, that I thanked
277𓀴her, but my duty was imperative, and that I must go. She then rose and
278𓀴dried her eyes, and taking a crucifix from her neck offered it to me. I
279𓀴did not know what to do, for, as an English Churchman, I have been
280𓀴taught to regard such things as in some measure idolatrous, and yet it
281𓀴seemed so ungracious to refuse an old lady meaning so well and in such a
282𓀴state of mind. She saw, I suppose, the doubt in my face, for she put the
283𓀴rosary round my neck, and said, “For your mother’s sake,” and went out
284𓀴of the room. I am writing up this part of the diary whilst I am waiting
285𓀴for the coach, which is, of course, late; and the crucifix is still
286𓀴round my neck. Whether it is the old lady’s fear, or the many ghostly
287𓀴traditions of this place, or the crucifix itself, I do not know, but I
288𓀴am not feeling nearly as easy in my mind as usual. If this book should
289𓀴ever reach Mina before I do, let it bring my good-bye. Here comes the
290𓀴coach!
291𓀴
292𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
293𓀴
294𓀴_5 May. The Castle._--The grey of the morning has passed, and the sun is
295𓀴high over the distant horizon, which seems jagged, whether with trees or
296𓀴hills I know not, for it is so far off that big things and little are
297𓀴mixed. I am not sleepy, and, as I am not to be called till I awake,
298𓀴naturally I write till sleep comes. There are many odd things to put
299𓀴down, and, lest who reads them may fancy that I dined too well before I
300𓀴left Bistritz, let me put down my dinner exactly. I dined on what they
301𓀴called “robber steak”--bits of bacon, onion, and beef, seasoned with red
302𓀴pepper, and strung on sticks and roasted over the fire, in the simple
303𓀴style of the London cat’s meat! The wine was Golden Mediasch, which
304𓀴produces a queer sting on the tongue, which is, however, not
305𓀴disagreeable. I had only a couple of glasses of this, and nothing else.
306𓀴
307𓀴When I got on the coach the driver had not taken his seat, and I saw him
308𓀴talking with the landlady. They were evidently talking of me, for every
309𓀴now and then they looked at me, and some of the people who were sitting
310𓀴on the bench outside the door--which they call by a name meaning
311𓀴“word-bearer”--came and listened, and then looked at me, most of them
312𓀴pityingly. I could hear a lot of words often repeated, queer words, for
313𓀴there were many nationalities in the crowd; so I quietly got my polyglot
314𓀴dictionary from my bag and looked them out. I must say they were not
315𓀴cheering to me, for amongst them were “Ordog”--Satan, “pokol”--hell,
316𓀴“stregoica”--witch, “vrolok” and “vlkoslak”--both of which mean the same
317𓀴thing, one being Slovak and the other Servian for something that is
318𓀴either were-wolf or vampire. (_Mem._, I must ask the Count about these
319𓀴superstitions)
320𓀴
321𓀴When we started, the crowd round the inn door, which had by this time
322𓀴swelled to a considerable size, all made the sign of the cross and
323𓀴pointed two fingers towards me. With some difficulty I got a
324𓀴fellow-passenger to tell me what they meant; he would not answer at
325𓀴first, but on learning that I was English, he explained that it was a
326𓀴charm or guard against the evil eye. This was not very pleasant for me,
327𓀴just starting for an unknown place to meet an unknown man; but every one
328𓀴seemed so kind-hearted, and so sorrowful, and so sympathetic that I
329𓀴could not but be touched. I shall never forget the last glimpse which I
330𓀴had of the inn-yard and its crowd of picturesque figures, all crossing
331𓀴themselves, as they stood round the wide archway, with its background of
332𓀴rich foliage of oleander and orange trees in green tubs clustered in the
333𓀴centre of the yard. Then our driver, whose wide linen drawers covered
334𓀴the whole front of the box-seat--“gotza” they call them--cracked his big
335𓀴whip over his four small horses, which ran abreast, and we set off on
336𓀴our journey.
337𓀴
338𓀴I soon lost sight and recollection of ghostly fears in the beauty of the
339𓀴scene as we drove along, although had I known the language, or rather
340𓀴languages, which my fellow-passengers were speaking, I might not have
341𓀴been able to throw them off so easily. Before us lay a green sloping
342𓀴land full of forests and woods, with here and there steep hills, crowned
343𓀴with clumps of trees or with farmhouses, the blank gable end to the
344𓀴road. There was everywhere a bewildering mass of fruit blossom--apple,
345𓀴plum, pear, cherry; and as we drove by I could see the green grass under
346𓀴the trees spangled with the fallen petals. In and out amongst these
347𓀴green hills of what they call here the “Mittel Land” ran the road,
348𓀴losing itself as it swept round the grassy curve, or was shut out by the
349𓀴straggling ends of pine woods, which here and there ran down the
350𓀴hillsides like tongues of flame. The road was rugged, but still we
351𓀴seemed to fly over it with a feverish haste. I could not understand then
352𓀴what the haste meant, but the driver was evidently bent on losing no
353𓀴time in reaching Borgo Prund. I was told that this road is in summertime
354𓀴excellent, but that it had not yet been put in order after the winter
355𓀴snows. In this respect it is different from the general run of roads in
356𓀴the Carpathians, for it is an old tradition that they are not to be kept
357𓀴in too good order. Of old the Hospadars would not repair them, lest the
358𓀴Turk should think that they were preparing to bring in foreign troops,
359𓀴and so hasten the war which was always really at loading point.
360𓀴
361𓀴Beyond the green swelling hills of the Mittel Land rose mighty slopes
362𓀴of forest up to the lofty steeps of the Carpathians themselves. Right
363𓀴and left of us they towered, with the afternoon sun falling full upon
364𓀴them and bringing out all the glorious colours of this beautiful range,
365𓀴deep blue and purple in the shadows of the peaks, green and brown where
366𓀴grass and rock mingled, and an endless perspective of jagged rock and
367𓀴pointed crags, till these were themselves lost in the distance, where
368𓀴the snowy peaks rose grandly. Here and there seemed mighty rifts in the
369𓀴mountains, through which, as the sun began to sink, we saw now and again
370𓀴the white gleam of falling water. One of my companions touched my arm as
371𓀴we swept round the base of a hill and opened up the lofty, snow-covered
372𓀴peak of a mountain, which seemed, as we wound on our serpentine way, to
373𓀴be right before us:--
374𓀴
375𓀴“Look! Isten szek!”--“God’s seat!”--and he crossed himself reverently.
376𓀴
377𓀴As we wound on our endless way, and the sun sank lower and lower behind
378𓀴us, the shadows of the evening began to creep round us. This was
379𓀴emphasised by the fact that the snowy mountain-top still held the
380𓀴sunset, and seemed to glow out with a delicate cool pink. Here and there
381𓀴we passed Cszeks and Slovaks, all in picturesque attire, but I noticed
382𓀴that goitre was painfully prevalent. By the roadside were many crosses,
383𓀴and as we swept by, my companions all crossed themselves. Here and there
384𓀴was a peasant man or woman kneeling before a shrine, who did not even
385𓀴turn round as we approached, but seemed in the self-surrender of
386𓀴devotion to have neither eyes nor ears for the outer world. There were
387𓀴many things new to me: for instance, hay-ricks in the trees, and here
388𓀴and there very beautiful masses of weeping birch, their white stems
389𓀴shining like silver through the delicate green of the leaves. Now and
390𓀴again we passed a leiter-wagon--the ordinary peasant’s cart--with its
391𓀴long, snake-like vertebra, calculated to suit the inequalities of the
392𓀴road. On this were sure to be seated quite a group of home-coming
393𓀴peasants, the Cszeks with their white, and the Slovaks with their
394𓀴coloured, sheepskins, the latter carrying lance-fashion their long
395𓀴staves, with axe at end. As the evening fell it began to get very cold,
396𓀴and the growing twilight seemed to merge into one dark mistiness the
397𓀴gloom of the trees, oak, beech, and pine, though in the valleys which
398𓀴ran deep between the spurs of the hills, as we ascended through the
399𓀴Pass, the dark firs stood out here and there against the background of
400𓀴late-lying snow. Sometimes, as the road was cut through the pine woods
401𓀴that seemed in the darkness to be closing down upon us, great masses of
402𓀴greyness, which here and there bestrewed the trees, produced a
403𓀴peculiarly weird and solemn effect, which carried on the thoughts and
404𓀴grim fancies engendered earlier in the evening, when the falling sunset
405𓀴threw into strange relief the ghost-like clouds which amongst the
406𓀴Carpathians seem to wind ceaselessly through the valleys. Sometimes the
407𓀴hills were so steep that, despite our driver’s haste, the horses could
408𓀴only go slowly. I wished to get down and walk up them, as we do at home,
409𓀴but the driver would not hear of it. “No, no,” he said; “you must not
410𓀴walk here; the dogs are too fierce”; and then he added, with what he
411𓀴evidently meant for grim pleasantry--for he looked round to catch the
412𓀴approving smile of the rest--“and you may have enough of such matters
413𓀴before you go to sleep.” The only stop he would make was a moment’s
414𓀴pause to light his lamps.
415𓀴
416𓀴When it grew dark there seemed to be some excitement amongst the
417𓀴passengers, and they kept speaking to him, one after the other, as
418𓀴though urging him to further speed. He lashed the horses unmercifully
419𓀴with his long whip, and with wild cries of encouragement urged them on
420𓀴to further exertions. Then through the darkness I could see a sort of
421𓀴patch of grey light ahead of us, as though there were a cleft in the
422𓀴hills. The excitement of the passengers grew greater; the crazy coach
423𓀴rocked on its great leather springs, and swayed like a boat tossed on a
424𓀴stormy sea. I had to hold on. The road grew more level, and we appeared
425𓀴to fly along. Then the mountains seemed to come nearer to us on each
426𓀴side and to frown down upon us; we were entering on the Borgo Pass. One
427𓀴by one several of the passengers offered me gifts, which they pressed
428𓀴upon me with an earnestness which would take no denial; these were
429𓀴certainly of an odd and varied kind, but each was given in simple good
430𓀴faith, with a kindly word, and a blessing, and that strange mixture of
431𓀴fear-meaning movements which I had seen outside the hotel at
432𓀴Bistritz--the sign of the cross and the guard against the evil eye.
433𓀴Then, as we flew along, the driver leaned forward, and on each side the
434𓀴passengers, craning over the edge of the coach, peered eagerly into the
435𓀴darkness. It was evident that something very exciting was either
436𓀴happening or expected, but though I asked each passenger, no one would
437𓀴give me the slightest explanation. This state of excitement kept on for
438𓀴some little time; and at last we saw before us the Pass opening out on
439𓀴the eastern side. There were dark, rolling clouds overhead, and in the
440𓀴air the heavy, oppressive sense of thunder. It seemed as though the
441𓀴mountain range had separated two atmospheres, and that now we had got
442𓀴into the thunderous one. I was now myself looking out for the conveyance
443𓀴which was to take me to the Count. Each moment I expected to see the
444𓀴glare of lamps through the blackness; but all was dark. The only light
445𓀴was the flickering rays of our own lamps, in which the steam from our
446𓀴hard-driven horses rose in a white cloud. We could see now the sandy
447𓀴road lying white before us, but there was on it no sign of a vehicle.
448𓀴The passengers drew back with a sigh of gladness, which seemed to mock
449𓀴my own disappointment. I was already thinking what I had best do, when
450𓀴the driver, looking at his watch, said to the others something which I
451𓀴could hardly hear, it was spoken so quietly and in so low a tone; I
452𓀴thought it was “An hour less than the time.” Then turning to me, he said
453𓀴in German worse than my own:--
454𓀴
455𓀴“There is no carriage here. The Herr is not expected after all. He will
456𓀴now come on to Bukovina, and return to-morrow or the next day; better
457𓀴the next day.” Whilst he was speaking the horses began to neigh and
458𓀴snort and plunge wildly, so that the driver had to hold them up. Then,
459𓀴amongst a chorus of screams from the peasants and a universal crossing
460𓀴of themselves, a calèche, with four horses, drove up behind us, overtook
461𓀴us, and drew up beside the coach. I could see from the flash of our
462𓀴lamps, as the rays fell on them, that the horses were coal-black and
463𓀴splendid animals. They were driven by a tall man, with a long brown
464𓀴beard and a great black hat, which seemed to hide his face from us. I
465𓀴could only see the gleam of a pair of very bright eyes, which seemed red
466𓀴in the lamplight, as he turned to us. He said to the driver:--
467𓀴
468𓀴“You are early to-night, my friend.” The man stammered in reply:--
469𓀴
470𓀴“The English Herr was in a hurry,” to which the stranger replied:--
471𓀴
472𓀴“That is why, I suppose, you wished him to go on to Bukovina. You cannot
473𓀴deceive me, my friend; I know too much, and my horses are swift.” As he
474𓀴spoke he smiled, and the lamplight fell on a hard-looking mouth, with
475𓀴very red lips and sharp-looking teeth, as white as ivory. One of my
476𓀴companions whispered to another the line from Burger’s “Lenore”:--
477𓀴
478𓀴    “Denn die Todten reiten schnell”--
479𓀴    (“For the dead travel fast.”)
480𓀴
481𓀴The strange driver evidently heard the words, for he looked up with a
482𓀴gleaming smile. The passenger turned his face away, at the same time
483𓀴putting out his two fingers and crossing himself. “Give me the Herr’s
484𓀴luggage,” said the driver; and with exceeding alacrity my bags were
485𓀴handed out and put in the calèche. Then I descended from the side of the
486𓀴coach, as the calèche was close alongside, the driver helping me with a
487𓀴hand which caught my arm in a grip of steel; his strength must have been
488𓀴prodigious. Without a word he shook his reins, the horses turned, and we
489𓀴swept into the darkness of the Pass. As I looked back I saw the steam
490𓀴from the horses of the coach by the light of the lamps, and projected
491𓀴against it the figures of my late companions crossing themselves. Then
492𓀴the driver cracked his whip and called to his horses, and off they swept
493𓀴on their way to Bukovina. As they sank into the darkness I felt a
494𓀴strange chill, and a lonely feeling came over me; but a cloak was thrown
495𓀴over my shoulders, and a rug across my knees, and the driver said in
496𓀴excellent German:--
497𓀴
498𓀴“The night is chill, mein Herr, and my master the Count bade me take all
499𓀴care of you. There is a flask of slivovitz (the plum brandy of the
500𓀴country) underneath the seat, if you should require it.” I did not take
501𓀴any, but it was a comfort to know it was there all the same. I felt a
502𓀴little strangely, and not a little frightened. I think had there been
503𓀴any alternative I should have taken it, instead of prosecuting that
504𓀴unknown night journey. The carriage went at a hard pace straight along,
505𓀴then we made a complete turn and went along another straight road. It
506𓀴seemed to me that we were simply going over and over the same ground
507𓀴again; and so I took note of some salient point, and found that this was
508𓀴so. I would have liked to have asked the driver what this all meant, but
509𓀴I really feared to do so, for I thought that, placed as I was, any
510𓀴protest would have had no effect in case there had been an intention to
511𓀴delay. By-and-by, however, as I was curious to know how time was
512𓀴passing, I struck a match, and by its flame looked at my watch; it was
513𓀴within a few minutes of midnight. This gave me a sort of shock, for I
514𓀴suppose the general superstition about midnight was increased by my
515𓀴recent experiences. I waited with a sick feeling of suspense.
516𓀴
517𓀴Then a dog began to howl somewhere in a farmhouse far down the road--a
518𓀴long, agonised wailing, as if from fear. The sound was taken up by
519𓀴another dog, and then another and another, till, borne on the wind which
520𓀴now sighed softly through the Pass, a wild howling began, which seemed
521𓀴to come from all over the country, as far as the imagination could grasp
522𓀴it through the gloom of the night. At the first howl the horses began to
523𓀴strain and rear, but the driver spoke to them soothingly, and they
524𓀴quieted down, but shivered and sweated as though after a runaway from
525𓀴sudden fright. Then, far off in the distance, from the mountains on each
526𓀴side of us began a louder and a sharper howling--that of wolves--which
527𓀴affected both the horses and myself in the same way--for I was minded to
528𓀴jump from the calèche and run, whilst they reared again and plunged
529𓀴madly, so that the driver had to use all his great strength to keep them
530𓀴from bolting. In a few minutes, however, my own ears got accustomed to
531𓀴the sound, and the horses so far became quiet that the driver was able
532𓀴to descend and to stand before them. He petted and soothed them, and
533𓀴whispered something in their ears, as I have heard of horse-tamers
534𓀴doing, and with extraordinary effect, for under his caresses they became
535𓀴quite manageable again, though they still trembled. The driver again
536𓀴took his seat, and shaking his reins, started off at a great pace. This
537𓀴time, after going to the far side of the Pass, he suddenly turned down a
538𓀴narrow roadway which ran sharply to the right.
539𓀴
540𓀴Soon we were hemmed in with trees, which in places arched right over the
541𓀴roadway till we passed as through a tunnel; and again great frowning
542𓀴rocks guarded us boldly on either side. Though we were in shelter, we
543𓀴could hear the rising wind, for it moaned and whistled through the
544𓀴rocks, and the branches of the trees crashed together as we swept along.
545𓀴It grew colder and colder still, and fine, powdery snow began to fall,
546𓀴so that soon we and all around us were covered with a white blanket. The
547𓀴keen wind still carried the howling of the dogs, though this grew
548𓀴fainter as we went on our way. The baying of the wolves sounded nearer
549𓀴and nearer, as though they were closing round on us from every side. I
550𓀴grew dreadfully afraid, and the horses shared my fear. The driver,
551𓀴however, was not in the least disturbed; he kept turning his head to
552𓀴left and right, but I could not see anything through the darkness.
553𓀴
554𓀴Suddenly, away on our left, I saw a faint flickering blue flame. The
555𓀴driver saw it at the same moment; he at once checked the horses, and,
556𓀴jumping to the ground, disappeared into the darkness. I did not know
557𓀴what to do, the less as the howling of the wolves grew closer; but while
558𓀴I wondered the driver suddenly appeared again, and without a word took
559𓀴his seat, and we resumed our journey. I think I must have fallen asleep
560𓀴and kept dreaming of the incident, for it seemed to be repeated
561𓀴endlessly, and now looking back, it is like a sort of awful nightmare.
562𓀴Once the flame appeared so near the road, that even in the darkness
563𓀴around us I could watch the driver’s motions. He went rapidly to where
564𓀴the blue flame arose--it must have been very faint, for it did not seem
565𓀴to illumine the place around it at all--and gathering a few stones,
566𓀴formed them into some device. Once there appeared a strange optical
567𓀴effect: when he stood between me and the flame he did not obstruct it,
568𓀴for I could see its ghostly flicker all the same. This startled me, but
569𓀴as the effect was only momentary, I took it that my eyes deceived me
570𓀴straining through the darkness. Then for a time there were no blue
571𓀴flames, and we sped onwards through the gloom, with the howling of the
572𓀴wolves around us, as though they were following in a moving circle.
573𓀴
574𓀴At last there came a time when the driver went further afield than he
575𓀴had yet gone, and during his absence, the horses began to tremble worse
576𓀴than ever and to snort and scream with fright. I could not see any cause
577𓀴for it, for the howling of the wolves had ceased altogether; but just
578𓀴then the moon, sailing through the black clouds, appeared behind the
579𓀴jagged crest of a beetling, pine-clad rock, and by its light I saw
580𓀴around us a ring of wolves, with white teeth and lolling red tongues,
581𓀴with long, sinewy limbs and shaggy hair. They were a hundred times more
582𓀴terrible in the grim silence which held them than even when they howled.
583𓀴For myself, I felt a sort of paralysis of fear. It is only when a man
584𓀴feels himself face to face with such horrors that he can understand
585𓀴their true import.
586𓀴
587𓀴All at once the wolves began to howl as though the moonlight had had
588𓀴some peculiar effect on them. The horses jumped about and reared, and
589𓀴looked helplessly round with eyes that rolled in a way painful to see;
590𓀴but the living ring of terror encompassed them on every side; and they
591𓀴had perforce to remain within it. I called to the coachman to come, for
592𓀴it seemed to me that our only chance was to try to break out through the
593𓀴ring and to aid his approach. I shouted and beat the side of the
594𓀴calèche, hoping by the noise to scare the wolves from that side, so as
595𓀴to give him a chance of reaching the trap. How he came there, I know
596𓀴not, but I heard his voice raised in a tone of imperious command, and
597𓀴looking towards the sound, saw him stand in the roadway. As he swept his
598𓀴long arms, as though brushing aside some impalpable obstacle, the wolves
599𓀴fell back and back further still. Just then a heavy cloud passed across
600𓀴the face of the moon, so that we were again in darkness.
601𓀴
602𓀴When I could see again the driver was climbing into the calèche, and the
603𓀴wolves had disappeared. This was all so strange and uncanny that a
604𓀴dreadful fear came upon me, and I was afraid to speak or move. The time
605𓀴seemed interminable as we swept on our way, now in almost complete
606𓀴darkness, for the rolling clouds obscured the moon. We kept on
607𓀴ascending, with occasional periods of quick descent, but in the main
608𓀴always ascending. Suddenly, I became conscious of the fact that the
609𓀴driver was in the act of pulling up the horses in the courtyard of a
610𓀴vast ruined castle, from whose tall black windows came no ray of light,
611𓀴and whose broken battlements showed a jagged line against the moonlit
612𓀴sky.
613𓀴
614𓀴
615𓀴
616𓀴
617𓀴CHAPTER II
618𓀴
619𓀴JONATHAN HARKER’S JOURNAL--_continued_
620𓀴
621𓀴
622𓀴_5 May._--I must have been asleep, for certainly if I had been fully
623𓀴awake I must have noticed the approach of such a remarkable place. In
624𓀴the gloom the courtyard looked of considerable size, and as several dark
625𓀴ways led from it under great round arches, it perhaps seemed bigger than
626𓀴it really is. I have not yet been able to see it by daylight.
627𓀴
628𓀴When the calèche stopped, the driver jumped down and held out his hand
629𓀴to assist me to alight. Again I could not but notice his prodigious
630𓀴strength. His hand actually seemed like a steel vice that could have
631𓀴crushed mine if he had chosen. Then he took out my traps, and placed
632𓀴them on the ground beside me as I stood close to a great door, old and
633𓀴studded with large iron nails, and set in a projecting doorway of
634𓀴massive stone. I could see even in the dim light that the stone was
635𓀴massively carved, but that the carving had been much worn by time and
636𓀴weather. As I stood, the driver jumped again into his seat and shook the
637𓀴reins; the horses started forward, and trap and all disappeared down one
638𓀴of the dark openings.
639𓀴
640𓀴I stood in silence where I was, for I did not know what to do. Of bell
641𓀴or knocker there was no sign; through these frowning walls and dark
642𓀴window openings it was not likely that my voice could penetrate. The
643𓀴time I waited seemed endless, and I felt doubts and fears crowding upon
644𓀴me. What sort of place had I come to, and among what kind of people?
645𓀴What sort of grim adventure was it on which I had embarked? Was this a
646𓀴customary incident in the life of a solicitor’s clerk sent out to
647𓀴explain the purchase of a London estate to a foreigner? Solicitor’s
648𓀴clerk! Mina would not like that. Solicitor--for just before leaving
649𓀴London I got word that my examination was successful; and I am now a
650𓀴full-blown solicitor! I began to rub my eyes and pinch myself to see if
651𓀴I were awake. It all seemed like a horrible nightmare to me, and I
652𓀴expected that I should suddenly awake, and find myself at home, with
653𓀴the dawn struggling in through the windows, as I had now and again felt
654𓀴in the morning after a day of overwork. But my flesh answered the
655𓀴pinching test, and my eyes were not to be deceived. I was indeed awake
656𓀴and among the Carpathians. All I could do now was to be patient, and to
657𓀴wait the coming of the morning.
658𓀴
659𓀴Just as I had come to this conclusion I heard a heavy step approaching
660𓀴behind the great door, and saw through the chinks the gleam of a coming
661𓀴light. Then there was the sound of rattling chains and the clanking of
662𓀴massive bolts drawn back. A key was turned with the loud grating noise
663𓀴of long disuse, and the great door swung back.
664𓀴
665𓀴Within, stood a tall old man, clean shaven save for a long white
666𓀴moustache, and clad in black from head to foot, without a single speck
667𓀴of colour about him anywhere. He held in his hand an antique silver
668𓀴lamp, in which the flame burned without chimney or globe of any kind,
669𓀴throwing long quivering shadows as it flickered in the draught of the
670𓀴open door. The old man motioned me in with his right hand with a courtly
671𓀴gesture, saying in excellent English, but with a strange intonation:--
672𓀴
673𓀴“Welcome to my house! Enter freely and of your own will!” He made no
674𓀴motion of stepping to meet me, but stood like a statue, as though his
675𓀴gesture of welcome had fixed him into stone. The instant, however, that
676𓀴I had stepped over the threshold, he moved impulsively forward, and
677𓀴holding out his hand grasped mine with a strength which made me wince,
678𓀴an effect which was not lessened by the fact that it seemed as cold as
679𓀴ice--more like the hand of a dead than a living man. Again he said:--
680𓀴
681𓀴“Welcome to my house. Come freely. Go safely; and leave something of the
682𓀴happiness you bring!” The strength of the handshake was so much akin to
683𓀴that which I had noticed in the driver, whose face I had not seen, that
684𓀴for a moment I doubted if it were not the same person to whom I was
685𓀴speaking; so to make sure, I said interrogatively:--
686𓀴
687𓀴“Count Dracula?” He bowed in a courtly way as he replied:--
688𓀴
689𓀴“I am Dracula; and I bid you welcome, Mr. Harker, to my house. Come in;
690𓀴the night air is chill, and you must need to eat and rest.” As he was
691𓀴speaking, he put the lamp on a bracket on the wall, and stepping out,
692𓀴took my luggage; he had carried it in before I could forestall him. I
693𓀴protested but he insisted:--
694𓀴
695𓀴“Nay, sir, you are my guest. It is late, and my people are not
696𓀴available. Let me see to your comfort myself.” He insisted on carrying
697𓀴my traps along the passage, and then up a great winding stair, and
698𓀴along another great passage, on whose stone floor our steps rang
699𓀴heavily. At the end of this he threw open a heavy door, and I rejoiced
700𓀴to see within a well-lit room in which a table was spread for supper,
701𓀴and on whose mighty hearth a great fire of logs, freshly replenished,
702𓀴flamed and flared.
703𓀴
704𓀴The Count halted, putting down my bags, closed the door, and crossing
705𓀴the room, opened another door, which led into a small octagonal room lit
706𓀴by a single lamp, and seemingly without a window of any sort. Passing
707𓀴through this, he opened another door, and motioned me to enter. It was a
708𓀴welcome sight; for here was a great bedroom well lighted and warmed with
709𓀴another log fire,--also added to but lately, for the top logs were
710𓀴fresh--which sent a hollow roar up the wide chimney. The Count himself
711𓀴left my luggage inside and withdrew, saying, before he closed the
712𓀴door:--
713𓀴
714𓀴“You will need, after your journey, to refresh yourself by making your
715𓀴toilet. I trust you will find all you wish. When you are ready, come
716𓀴into the other room, where you will find your supper prepared.”
717𓀴
718𓀴The light and warmth and the Count’s courteous welcome seemed to have
719𓀴dissipated all my doubts and fears. Having then reached my normal state,
720𓀴I discovered that I was half famished with hunger; so making a hasty
721𓀴toilet, I went into the other room.
722𓀴
723𓀴I found supper already laid out. My host, who stood on one side of the
724𓀴great fireplace, leaning against the stonework, made a graceful wave of
725𓀴his hand to the table, and said:--
726𓀴
727𓀴“I pray you, be seated and sup how you please. You will, I trust, excuse
728𓀴me that I do not join you; but I have dined already, and I do not sup.”
729𓀴
730𓀴I handed to him the sealed letter which Mr. Hawkins had entrusted to me.
731𓀴He opened it and read it gravely; then, with a charming smile, he handed
732𓀴it to me to read. One passage of it, at least, gave me a thrill of
733𓀴pleasure.
734𓀴
735𓀴“I must regret that an attack of gout, from which malady I am a constant
736𓀴sufferer, forbids absolutely any travelling on my part for some time to
737𓀴come; but I am happy to say I can send a sufficient substitute, one in
738𓀴whom I have every possible confidence. He is a young man, full of energy
739𓀴and talent in his own way, and of a very faithful disposition. He is
740𓀴discreet and silent, and has grown into manhood in my service. He shall
741𓀴be ready to attend on you when you will during his stay, and shall take
742𓀴your instructions in all matters.”
743𓀴
744𓀴The Count himself came forward and took off the cover of a dish, and I
745𓀴fell to at once on an excellent roast chicken. This, with some cheese
746𓀴and a salad and a bottle of old Tokay, of which I had two glasses, was
747𓀴my supper. During the time I was eating it the Count asked me many
748𓀴questions as to my journey, and I told him by degrees all I had
749𓀴experienced.
750𓀴
751𓀴By this time I had finished my supper, and by my host’s desire had drawn
752𓀴up a chair by the fire and begun to smoke a cigar which he offered me,
753𓀴at the same time excusing himself that he did not smoke. I had now an
754𓀴opportunity of observing him, and found him of a very marked
755𓀴physiognomy.
756𓀴
757𓀴His face was a strong--a very strong--aquiline, with high bridge of the
758𓀴thin nose and peculiarly arched nostrils; with lofty domed forehead, and
759𓀴hair growing scantily round the temples but profusely elsewhere. His
760𓀴eyebrows were very massive, almost meeting over the nose, and with bushy
761𓀴hair that seemed to curl in its own profusion. The mouth, so far as I
762𓀴could see it under the heavy moustache, was fixed and rather
763𓀴cruel-looking, with peculiarly sharp white teeth; these protruded over
764𓀴the lips, whose remarkable ruddiness showed astonishing vitality in a
765𓀴man of his years. For the rest, his ears were pale, and at the tops
766𓀴extremely pointed; the chin was broad and strong, and the cheeks firm
767𓀴though thin. The general effect was one of extraordinary pallor.
768𓀴
769𓀴Hitherto I had noticed the backs of his hands as they lay on his knees
770𓀴in the firelight, and they had seemed rather white and fine; but seeing
771𓀴them now close to me, I could not but notice that they were rather
772𓀴coarse--broad, with squat fingers. Strange to say, there were hairs in
773𓀴the centre of the palm. The nails were long and fine, and cut to a sharp
774𓀴point. As the Count leaned over me and his hands touched me, I could not
775𓀴repress a shudder. It may have been that his breath was rank, but a
776𓀴horrible feeling of nausea came over me, which, do what I would, I could
777𓀴not conceal. The Count, evidently noticing it, drew back; and with a
778𓀴grim sort of smile, which showed more than he had yet done his
779𓀴protuberant teeth, sat himself down again on his own side of the
780𓀴fireplace. We were both silent for a while; and as I looked towards the
781𓀴window I saw the first dim streak of the coming dawn. There seemed a
782𓀴strange stillness over everything; but as I listened I heard as if from
783𓀴down below in the valley the howling of many wolves. The Count’s eyes
784𓀴gleamed, and he said:--
785𓀴
786𓀴“Listen to them--the children of the night. What music they make!”
787𓀴Seeing, I suppose, some expression in my face strange to him, he
788𓀴added:--
789𓀴
790𓀴“Ah, sir, you dwellers in the city cannot enter into the feelings of the
791𓀴hunter.” Then he rose and said:--
792𓀴
793𓀴“But you must be tired. Your bedroom is all ready, and to-morrow you
794𓀴shall sleep as late as you will. I have to be away till the afternoon;
795𓀴so sleep well and dream well!” With a courteous bow, he opened for me
796𓀴himself the door to the octagonal room, and I entered my bedroom....
797𓀴
798𓀴I am all in a sea of wonders. I doubt; I fear; I think strange things,
799𓀴which I dare not confess to my own soul. God keep me, if only for the
800𓀴sake of those dear to me!
801𓀴
802𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
803𓀴
804𓀴_7 May._--It is again early morning, but I have rested and enjoyed the
805𓀴last twenty-four hours. I slept till late in the day, and awoke of my
806𓀴own accord. When I had dressed myself I went into the room where we had
807𓀴supped, and found a cold breakfast laid out, with coffee kept hot by the
808𓀴pot being placed on the hearth. There was a card on the table, on which
809𓀴was written:--
810𓀴
811𓀴“I have to be absent for a while. Do not wait for me.--D.” I set to and
812𓀴enjoyed a hearty meal. When I had done, I looked for a bell, so that I
813𓀴might let the servants know I had finished; but I could not find one.
814𓀴There are certainly odd deficiencies in the house, considering the
815𓀴extraordinary evidences of wealth which are round me. The table service
816𓀴is of gold, and so beautifully wrought that it must be of immense value.
817𓀴The curtains and upholstery of the chairs and sofas and the hangings of
818𓀴my bed are of the costliest and most beautiful fabrics, and must have
819𓀴been of fabulous value when they were made, for they are centuries old,
820𓀴though in excellent order. I saw something like them in Hampton Court,
821𓀴but there they were worn and frayed and moth-eaten. But still in none of
822𓀴the rooms is there a mirror. There is not even a toilet glass on my
823𓀴table, and I had to get the little shaving glass from my bag before I
824𓀴could either shave or brush my hair. I have not yet seen a servant
825𓀴anywhere, or heard a sound near the castle except the howling of wolves.
826𓀴Some time after I had finished my meal--I do not know whether to call it
827𓀴breakfast or dinner, for it was between five and six o’clock when I had
828𓀴it--I looked about for something to read, for I did not like to go about
829𓀴the castle until I had asked the Count’s permission. There was
830𓀴absolutely nothing in the room, book, newspaper, or even writing
831𓀴materials; so I opened another door in the room and found a sort of
832𓀴library. The door opposite mine I tried, but found it locked.
833𓀴
834𓀴In the library I found, to my great delight, a vast number of English
835𓀴books, whole shelves full of them, and bound volumes of magazines and
836𓀴newspapers. A table in the centre was littered with English magazines
837𓀴and newspapers, though none of them were of very recent date. The books
838𓀴were of the most varied kind--history, geography, politics, political
839𓀴economy, botany, geology, law--all relating to England and English life
840𓀴and customs and manners. There were even such books of reference as the
841𓀴London Directory, the “Red” and “Blue” books, Whitaker’s Almanac, the
842𓀴Army and Navy Lists, and--it somehow gladdened my heart to see it--the
843𓀴Law List.
844𓀴
845𓀴Whilst I was looking at the books, the door opened, and the Count
846𓀴entered. He saluted me in a hearty way, and hoped that I had had a good
847𓀴night’s rest. Then he went on:--
848𓀴
849𓀴“I am glad you found your way in here, for I am sure there is much that
850𓀴will interest you. These companions”--and he laid his hand on some of
851𓀴the books--“have been good friends to me, and for some years past, ever
852𓀴since I had the idea of going to London, have given me many, many hours
853𓀴of pleasure. Through them I have come to know your great England; and to
854𓀴know her is to love her. I long to go through the crowded streets of
855𓀴your mighty London, to be in the midst of the whirl and rush of
856𓀴humanity, to share its life, its change, its death, and all that makes
857𓀴it what it is. But alas! as yet I only know your tongue through books.
858𓀴To you, my friend, I look that I know it to speak.”
859𓀴
860𓀴“But, Count,” I said, “you know and speak English thoroughly!” He bowed
861𓀴gravely.
862𓀴
863𓀴“I thank you, my friend, for your all too-flattering estimate, but yet I
864𓀴fear that I am but a little way on the road I would travel. True, I know
865𓀴the grammar and the words, but yet I know not how to speak them.”
866𓀴
867𓀴“Indeed,” I said, “you speak excellently.”
868𓀴
869𓀴“Not so,” he answered. “Well, I know that, did I move and speak in your
870𓀴London, none there are who would not know me for a stranger. That is not
871𓀴enough for me. Here I am noble; I am _boyar_; the common people know me,
872𓀴and I am master. But a stranger in a strange land, he is no one; men
873𓀴know him not--and to know not is to care not for. I am content if I am
874𓀴like the rest, so that no man stops if he see me, or pause in his
875𓀴speaking if he hear my words, ‘Ha, ha! a stranger!’ I have been so long
876𓀴master that I would be master still--or at least that none other should
877𓀴be master of me. You come to me not alone as agent of my friend Peter
878𓀴Hawkins, of Exeter, to tell me all about my new estate in London. You
879𓀴shall, I trust, rest here with me awhile, so that by our talking I may
880𓀴learn the English intonation; and I would that you tell me when I make
881𓀴error, even of the smallest, in my speaking. I am sorry that I had to be
882𓀴away so long to-day; but you will, I know, forgive one who has so many
883𓀴important affairs in hand.”
884𓀴
885𓀴Of course I said all I could about being willing, and asked if I might
886𓀴come into that room when I chose. He answered: “Yes, certainly,” and
887𓀴added:--
888𓀴
889𓀴“You may go anywhere you wish in the castle, except where the doors are
890𓀴locked, where of course you will not wish to go. There is reason that
891𓀴all things are as they are, and did you see with my eyes and know with
892𓀴my knowledge, you would perhaps better understand.” I said I was sure of
893𓀴this, and then he went on:--
894𓀴
895𓀴“We are in Transylvania; and Transylvania is not England. Our ways are
896𓀴not your ways, and there shall be to you many strange things. Nay, from
897𓀴what you have told me of your experiences already, you know something of
898𓀴what strange things there may be.”
899𓀴
900𓀴This led to much conversation; and as it was evident that he wanted to
901𓀴talk, if only for talking’s sake, I asked him many questions regarding
902𓀴things that had already happened to me or come within my notice.
903𓀴Sometimes he sheered off the subject, or turned the conversation by
904𓀴pretending not to understand; but generally he answered all I asked most
905𓀴frankly. Then as time went on, and I had got somewhat bolder, I asked
906𓀴him of some of the strange things of the preceding night, as, for
907𓀴instance, why the coachman went to the places where he had seen the blue
908𓀴flames. He then explained to me that it was commonly believed that on a
909𓀴certain night of the year--last night, in fact, when all evil spirits
910𓀴are supposed to have unchecked sway--a blue flame is seen over any place
911𓀴where treasure has been concealed. “That treasure has been hidden,” he
912𓀴went on, “in the region through which you came last night, there can be
913𓀴but little doubt; for it was the ground fought over for centuries by the
914𓀴Wallachian, the Saxon, and the Turk. Why, there is hardly a foot of soil
915𓀴in all this region that has not been enriched by the blood of men,
916𓀴patriots or invaders. In old days there were stirring times, when the
917𓀴Austrian and the Hungarian came up in hordes, and the patriots went out
918𓀴to meet them--men and women, the aged and the children too--and waited
919𓀴their coming on the rocks above the passes, that they might sweep
920𓀴destruction on them with their artificial avalanches. When the invader
921𓀴was triumphant he found but little, for whatever there was had been
922𓀴sheltered in the friendly soil.”
923𓀴
924𓀴“But how,” said I, “can it have remained so long undiscovered, when
925𓀴there is a sure index to it if men will but take the trouble to look?”
926𓀴The Count smiled, and as his lips ran back over his gums, the long,
927𓀴sharp, canine teeth showed out strangely; he answered:--
928𓀴
929𓀴“Because your peasant is at heart a coward and a fool! Those flames only
930𓀴appear on one night; and on that night no man of this land will, if he
931𓀴can help it, stir without his doors. And, dear sir, even if he did he
932𓀴would not know what to do. Why, even the peasant that you tell me of who
933𓀴marked the place of the flame would not know where to look in daylight
934𓀴even for his own work. Even you would not, I dare be sworn, be able to
935𓀴find these places again?”
936𓀴
937𓀴“There you are right,” I said. “I know no more than the dead where even
938𓀴to look for them.” Then we drifted into other matters.
939𓀴
940𓀴“Come,” he said at last, “tell me of London and of the house which you
941𓀴have procured for me.” With an apology for my remissness, I went into my
942𓀴own room to get the papers from my bag. Whilst I was placing them in
943𓀴order I heard a rattling of china and silver in the next room, and as I
944𓀴passed through, noticed that the table had been cleared and the lamp
945𓀴lit, for it was by this time deep into the dark. The lamps were also lit
946𓀴in the study or library, and I found the Count lying on the sofa,
947𓀴reading, of all things in the world, an English Bradshaw’s Guide. When I
948𓀴came in he cleared the books and papers from the table; and with him I
949𓀴went into plans and deeds and figures of all sorts. He was interested in
950𓀴everything, and asked me a myriad questions about the place and its
951𓀴surroundings. He clearly had studied beforehand all he could get on the
952𓀴subject of the neighbourhood, for he evidently at the end knew very much
953𓀴more than I did. When I remarked this, he answered:--
954𓀴
955𓀴“Well, but, my friend, is it not needful that I should? When I go there
956𓀴I shall be all alone, and my friend Harker Jonathan--nay, pardon me, I
957𓀴fall into my country’s habit of putting your patronymic first--my friend
958𓀴Jonathan Harker will not be by my side to correct and aid me. He will be
959𓀴in Exeter, miles away, probably working at papers of the law with my
960𓀴other friend, Peter Hawkins. So!”
961𓀴
962𓀴We went thoroughly into the business of the purchase of the estate at
963𓀴Purfleet. When I had told him the facts and got his signature to the
964𓀴necessary papers, and had written a letter with them ready to post to
965𓀴Mr. Hawkins, he began to ask me how I had come across so suitable a
966𓀴place. I read to him the notes which I had made at the time, and which I
967𓀴inscribe here:--
968𓀴
969𓀴“At Purfleet, on a by-road, I came across just such a place as seemed to
970𓀴be required, and where was displayed a dilapidated notice that the place
971𓀴was for sale. It is surrounded by a high wall, of ancient structure,
972𓀴built of heavy stones, and has not been repaired for a large number of
973𓀴years. The closed gates are of heavy old oak and iron, all eaten with
974𓀴rust.
975𓀴
976𓀴“The estate is called Carfax, no doubt a corruption of the old _Quatre
977𓀴Face_, as the house is four-sided, agreeing with the cardinal points of
978𓀴the compass. It contains in all some twenty acres, quite surrounded by
979𓀴the solid stone wall above mentioned. There are many trees on it, which
980𓀴make it in places gloomy, and there is a deep, dark-looking pond or
981𓀴small lake, evidently fed by some springs, as the water is clear and
982𓀴flows away in a fair-sized stream. The house is very large and of all
983𓀴periods back, I should say, to mediæval times, for one part is of stone
984𓀴immensely thick, with only a few windows high up and heavily barred with
985𓀴iron. It looks like part of a keep, and is close to an old chapel or
986𓀴church. I could not enter it, as I had not the key of the door leading
987𓀴to it from the house, but I have taken with my kodak views of it from
988𓀴various points. The house has been added to, but in a very straggling
989𓀴way, and I can only guess at the amount of ground it covers, which must
990𓀴be very great. There are but few houses close at hand, one being a very
991𓀴large house only recently added to and formed into a private lunatic
992𓀴asylum. It is not, however, visible from the grounds.”
993𓀴
994𓀴When I had finished, he said:--
995𓀴
996𓀴“I am glad that it is old and big. I myself am of an old family, and to
997𓀴live in a new house would kill me. A house cannot be made habitable in a
998𓀴day; and, after all, how few days go to make up a century. I rejoice
999𓀴also that there is a chapel of old times. We Transylvanian nobles love
1000𓀴not to think that our bones may lie amongst the common dead. I seek not
1001𓀴gaiety nor mirth, not the bright voluptuousness of much sunshine and
1002𓀴sparkling waters which please the young and gay. I am no longer young;
1003𓀴and my heart, through weary years of mourning over the dead, is not
1004𓀴attuned to mirth. Moreover, the walls of my castle are broken; the
1005𓀴shadows are many, and the wind breathes cold through the broken
1006𓀴battlements and casements. I love the shade and the shadow, and would
1007𓀴be alone with my thoughts when I may.” Somehow his words and his look
1008𓀴did not seem to accord, or else it was that his cast of face made his
1009𓀴smile look malignant and saturnine.
1010𓀴
1011𓀴Presently, with an excuse, he left me, asking me to put all my papers
1012𓀴together. He was some little time away, and I began to look at some of
1013𓀴the books around me. One was an atlas, which I found opened naturally at
1014𓀴England, as if that map had been much used. On looking at it I found in
1015𓀴certain places little rings marked, and on examining these I noticed
1016𓀴that one was near London on the east side, manifestly where his new
1017𓀴estate was situated; the other two were Exeter, and Whitby on the
1018𓀴Yorkshire coast.
1019𓀴
1020𓀴It was the better part of an hour when the Count returned. “Aha!” he
1021𓀴said; “still at your books? Good! But you must not work always. Come; I
1022𓀴am informed that your supper is ready.” He took my arm, and we went into
1023𓀴the next room, where I found an excellent supper ready on the table. The
1024𓀴Count again excused himself, as he had dined out on his being away from
1025𓀴home. But he sat as on the previous night, and chatted whilst I ate.
1026𓀴After supper I smoked, as on the last evening, and the Count stayed with
1027𓀴me, chatting and asking questions on every conceivable subject, hour
1028𓀴after hour. I felt that it was getting very late indeed, but I did not
1029𓀴say anything, for I felt under obligation to meet my host’s wishes in
1030𓀴every way. I was not sleepy, as the long sleep yesterday had fortified
1031𓀴me; but I could not help experiencing that chill which comes over one at
1032𓀴the coming of the dawn, which is like, in its way, the turn of the tide.
1033𓀴They say that people who are near death die generally at the change to
1034𓀴the dawn or at the turn of the tide; any one who has when tired, and
1035𓀴tied as it were to his post, experienced this change in the atmosphere
1036𓀴can well believe it. All at once we heard the crow of a cock coming up
1037𓀴with preternatural shrillness through the clear morning air; Count
1038𓀴Dracula, jumping to his feet, said:--
1039𓀴
1040𓀴“Why, there is the morning again! How remiss I am to let you stay up so
1041𓀴long. You must make your conversation regarding my dear new country of
1042𓀴England less interesting, so that I may not forget how time flies by
1043𓀴us,” and, with a courtly bow, he quickly left me.
1044𓀴
1045𓀴I went into my own room and drew the curtains, but there was little to
1046𓀴notice; my window opened into the courtyard, all I could see was the
1047𓀴warm grey of quickening sky. So I pulled the curtains again, and have
1048𓀴written of this day.
1049𓀴
1050𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
1051𓀴
1052𓀴_8 May._--I began to fear as I wrote in this book that I was getting too
1053𓀴diffuse; but now I am glad that I went into detail from the first, for
1054𓀴there is something so strange about this place and all in it that I
1055𓀴cannot but feel uneasy. I wish I were safe out of it, or that I had
1056𓀴never come. It may be that this strange night-existence is telling on
1057𓀴me; but would that that were all! If there were any one to talk to I
1058𓀴could bear it, but there is no one. I have only the Count to speak with,
1059𓀴and he!--I fear I am myself the only living soul within the place. Let
1060𓀴me be prosaic so far as facts can be; it will help me to bear up, and
1061𓀴imagination must not run riot with me. If it does I am lost. Let me say
1062𓀴at once how I stand--or seem to.
1063𓀴
1064𓀴I only slept a few hours when I went to bed, and feeling that I could
1065𓀴not sleep any more, got up. I had hung my shaving glass by the window,
1066𓀴and was just beginning to shave. Suddenly I felt a hand on my shoulder,
1067𓀴and heard the Count’s voice saying to me, “Good-morning.” I started, for
1068𓀴it amazed me that I had not seen him, since the reflection of the glass
1069𓀴covered the whole room behind me. In starting I had cut myself slightly,
1070𓀴but did not notice it at the moment. Having answered the Count’s
1071𓀴salutation, I turned to the glass again to see how I had been mistaken.
1072𓀴This time there could be no error, for the man was close to me, and I
1073𓀴could see him over my shoulder. But there was no reflection of him in
1074𓀴the mirror! The whole room behind me was displayed; but there was no
1075𓀴sign of a man in it, except myself. This was startling, and, coming on
1076𓀴the top of so many strange things, was beginning to increase that vague
1077𓀴feeling of uneasiness which I always have when the Count is near; but at
1078𓀴the instant I saw that the cut had bled a little, and the blood was
1079𓀴trickling over my chin. I laid down the razor, turning as I did so half
1080𓀴round to look for some sticking plaster. When the Count saw my face, his
1081𓀴eyes blazed with a sort of demoniac fury, and he suddenly made a grab at
1082𓀴my throat. I drew away, and his hand touched the string of beads which
1083𓀴held the crucifix. It made an instant change in him, for the fury passed
1084𓀴so quickly that I could hardly believe that it was ever there.
1085𓀴
1086𓀴“Take care,” he said, “take care how you cut yourself. It is more
1087𓀴dangerous than you think in this country.” Then seizing the shaving
1088𓀴glass, he went on: “And this is the wretched thing that has done the
1089𓀴mischief. It is a foul bauble of man’s vanity. Away with it!” and
1090𓀴opening the heavy window with one wrench of his terrible hand, he flung
1091𓀴out the glass, which was shattered into a thousand pieces on the stones
1092𓀴of the courtyard far below. Then he withdrew without a word. It is very
1093𓀴annoying, for I do not see how I am to shave, unless in my watch-case or
1094𓀴the bottom of the shaving-pot, which is fortunately of metal.
1095𓀴
1096𓀴When I went into the dining-room, breakfast was prepared; but I could
1097𓀴not find the Count anywhere. So I breakfasted alone. It is strange that
1098𓀴as yet I have not seen the Count eat or drink. He must be a very
1099𓀴peculiar man! After breakfast I did a little exploring in the castle. I
1100𓀴went out on the stairs, and found a room looking towards the South. The
1101𓀴view was magnificent, and from where I stood there was every opportunity
1102𓀴of seeing it. The castle is on the very edge of a terrible precipice. A
1103𓀴stone falling from the window would fall a thousand feet without
1104𓀴touching anything! As far as the eye can reach is a sea of green tree
1105𓀴tops, with occasionally a deep rift where there is a chasm. Here and
1106𓀴there are silver threads where the rivers wind in deep gorges through
1107𓀴the forests.
1108𓀴
1109𓀴But I am not in heart to describe beauty, for when I had seen the view I
1110𓀴explored further; doors, doors, doors everywhere, and all locked and
1111𓀴bolted. In no place save from the windows in the castle walls is there
1112𓀴an available exit.
1113𓀴
1114𓀴The castle is a veritable prison, and I am a prisoner!
1115𓀴
1116𓀴
1117𓀴
1118𓀴
1119𓀴CHAPTER III
1120𓀴
1121𓀴JONATHAN HARKER’S JOURNAL--_continued_
1122𓀴
1123𓀴
1124𓀴When I found that I was a prisoner a sort of wild feeling came over me.
1125𓀴I rushed up and down the stairs, trying every door and peering out of
1126𓀴every window I could find; but after a little the conviction of my
1127𓀴helplessness overpowered all other feelings. When I look back after a
1128𓀴few hours I think I must have been mad for the time, for I behaved much
1129𓀴as a rat does in a trap. When, however, the conviction had come to me
1130𓀴that I was helpless I sat down quietly--as quietly as I have ever done
1131𓀴anything in my life--and began to think over what was best to be done. I
1132𓀴am thinking still, and as yet have come to no definite conclusion. Of
1133𓀴one thing only am I certain; that it is no use making my ideas known to
1134𓀴the Count. He knows well that I am imprisoned; and as he has done it
1135𓀴himself, and has doubtless his own motives for it, he would only deceive
1136𓀴me if I trusted him fully with the facts. So far as I can see, my only
1137𓀴plan will be to keep my knowledge and my fears to myself, and my eyes
1138𓀴open. I am, I know, either being deceived, like a baby, by my own fears,
1139𓀴or else I am in desperate straits; and if the latter be so, I need, and
1140𓀴shall need, all my brains to get through.
1141𓀴
1142𓀴I had hardly come to this conclusion when I heard the great door below
1143𓀴shut, and knew that the Count had returned. He did not come at once into
1144𓀴the library, so I went cautiously to my own room and found him making
1145𓀴the bed. This was odd, but only confirmed what I had all along
1146𓀴thought--that there were no servants in the house. When later I saw him
1147𓀴through the chink of the hinges of the door laying the table in the
1148𓀴dining-room, I was assured of it; for if he does himself all these
1149𓀴menial offices, surely it is proof that there is no one else to do them.
1150𓀴This gave me a fright, for if there is no one else in the castle, it
1151𓀴must have been the Count himself who was the driver of the coach that
1152𓀴brought me here. This is a terrible thought; for if so, what does it
1153𓀴mean that he could control the wolves, as he did, by only holding up his
1154𓀴hand in silence. How was it that all the people at Bistritz and on the
1155𓀴coach had some terrible fear for me? What meant the giving of the
1156𓀴crucifix, of the garlic, of the wild rose, of the mountain ash? Bless
1157𓀴that good, good woman who hung the crucifix round my neck! for it is a
1158𓀴comfort and a strength to me whenever I touch it. It is odd that a thing
1159𓀴which I have been taught to regard with disfavour and as idolatrous
1160𓀴should in a time of loneliness and trouble be of help. Is it that there
1161𓀴is something in the essence of the thing itself, or that it is a medium,
1162𓀴a tangible help, in conveying memories of sympathy and comfort? Some
1163𓀴time, if it may be, I must examine this matter and try to make up my
1164𓀴mind about it. In the meantime I must find out all I can about Count
1165𓀴Dracula, as it may help me to understand. To-night he may talk of
1166𓀴himself, if I turn the conversation that way. I must be very careful,
1167𓀴however, not to awake his suspicion.
1168𓀴
1169𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
1170𓀴
1171𓀴_Midnight._--I have had a long talk with the Count. I asked him a few
1172𓀴questions on Transylvania history, and he warmed up to the subject
1173𓀴wonderfully. In his speaking of things and people, and especially of
1174𓀴battles, he spoke as if he had been present at them all. This he
1175𓀴afterwards explained by saying that to a _boyar_ the pride of his house
1176𓀴and name is his own pride, that their glory is his glory, that their
1177𓀴fate is his fate. Whenever he spoke of his house he always said “we,”
1178𓀴and spoke almost in the plural, like a king speaking. I wish I could put
1179𓀴down all he said exactly as he said it, for to me it was most
1180𓀴fascinating. It seemed to have in it a whole history of the country. He
1181𓀴grew excited as he spoke, and walked about the room pulling his great
1182𓀴white moustache and grasping anything on which he laid his hands as
1183𓀴though he would crush it by main strength. One thing he said which I
1184𓀴shall put down as nearly as I can; for it tells in its way the story of
1185𓀴his race:--
1186𓀴
1187𓀴“We Szekelys have a right to be proud, for in our veins flows the blood
1188𓀴of many brave races who fought as the lion fights, for lordship. Here,
1189𓀴in the whirlpool of European races, the Ugric tribe bore down from
1190𓀴Iceland the fighting spirit which Thor and Wodin gave them, which their
1191𓀴Berserkers displayed to such fell intent on the seaboards of Europe, ay,
1192𓀴and of Asia and Africa too, till the peoples thought that the
1193𓀴were-wolves themselves had come. Here, too, when they came, they found
1194𓀴the Huns, whose warlike fury had swept the earth like a living flame,
1195𓀴till the dying peoples held that in their veins ran the blood of those
1196𓀴old witches, who, expelled from Scythia had mated with the devils in the
1197𓀴desert. Fools, fools! What devil or what witch was ever so great as
1198𓀴Attila, whose blood is in these veins?” He held up his arms. “Is it a
1199𓀴wonder that we were a conquering race; that we were proud; that when the
1200𓀴Magyar, the Lombard, the Avar, the Bulgar, or the Turk poured his
1201𓀴thousands on our frontiers, we drove them back? Is it strange that when
1202𓀴Arpad and his legions swept through the Hungarian fatherland he found us
1203𓀴here when he reached the frontier; that the Honfoglalas was completed
1204𓀴there? And when the Hungarian flood swept eastward, the Szekelys were
1205𓀴claimed as kindred by the victorious Magyars, and to us for centuries
1206𓀴was trusted the guarding of the frontier of Turkey-land; ay, and more
1207𓀴than that, endless duty of the frontier guard, for, as the Turks say,
1208𓀴‘water sleeps, and enemy is sleepless.’ Who more gladly than we
1209𓀴throughout the Four Nations received the ‘bloody sword,’ or at its
1210𓀴warlike call flocked quicker to the standard of the King? When was
1211𓀴redeemed that great shame of my nation, the shame of Cassova, when the
1212𓀴flags of the Wallach and the Magyar went down beneath the Crescent? Who
1213𓀴was it but one of my own race who as Voivode crossed the Danube and beat
1214𓀴the Turk on his own ground? This was a Dracula indeed! Woe was it that
1215𓀴his own unworthy brother, when he had fallen, sold his people to the
1216𓀴Turk and brought the shame of slavery on them! Was it not this Dracula,
1217𓀴indeed, who inspired that other of his race who in a later age again and
1218𓀴again brought his forces over the great river into Turkey-land; who,
1219𓀴when he was beaten back, came again, and again, and again, though he had
1220𓀴to come alone from the bloody field where his troops were being
1221𓀴slaughtered, since he knew that he alone could ultimately triumph! They
1222𓀴said that he thought only of himself. Bah! what good are peasants
1223𓀴without a leader? Where ends the war without a brain and heart to
1224𓀴conduct it? Again, when, after the battle of Mohács, we threw off the
1225𓀴Hungarian yoke, we of the Dracula blood were amongst their leaders, for
1226𓀴our spirit would not brook that we were not free. Ah, young sir, the
1227𓀴Szekelys--and the Dracula as their heart’s blood, their brains, and
1228𓀴their swords--can boast a record that mushroom growths like the
1229𓀴Hapsburgs and the Romanoffs can never reach. The warlike days are over.
1230𓀴Blood is too precious a thing in these days of dishonourable peace; and
1231𓀴the glories of the great races are as a tale that is told.”
1232𓀴
1233𓀴It was by this time close on morning, and we went to bed. (_Mem._, this
1234𓀴diary seems horribly like the beginning of the “Arabian Nights,” for
1235𓀴everything has to break off at cockcrow--or like the ghost of Hamlet’s
1236𓀴father.)
1237𓀴
1238𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
1239𓀴
1240𓀴_12 May._--Let me begin with facts--bare, meagre facts, verified by
1241𓀴books and figures, and of which there can be no doubt. I must not
1242𓀴confuse them with experiences which will have to rest on my own
1243𓀴observation, or my memory of them. Last evening when the Count came from
1244𓀴his room he began by asking me questions on legal matters and on the
1245𓀴doing of certain kinds of business. I had spent the day wearily over
1246𓀴books, and, simply to keep my mind occupied, went over some of the
1247𓀴matters I had been examining at Lincoln’s Inn. There was a certain
1248𓀴method in the Count’s inquiries, so I shall try to put them down in
1249𓀴sequence; the knowledge may somehow or some time be useful to me.
1250𓀴
1251𓀴First, he asked if a man in England might have two solicitors or more. I
1252𓀴told him he might have a dozen if he wished, but that it would not be
1253𓀴wise to have more than one solicitor engaged in one transaction, as only
1254𓀴one could act at a time, and that to change would be certain to militate
1255𓀴against his interest. He seemed thoroughly to understand, and went on to
1256𓀴ask if there would be any practical difficulty in having one man to
1257𓀴attend, say, to banking, and another to look after shipping, in case
1258𓀴local help were needed in a place far from the home of the banking
1259𓀴solicitor. I asked him to explain more fully, so that I might not by any
1260𓀴chance mislead him, so he said:--
1261𓀴
1262𓀴“I shall illustrate. Your friend and mine, Mr. Peter Hawkins, from under
1263𓀴the shadow of your beautiful cathedral at Exeter, which is far from
1264𓀴London, buys for me through your good self my place at London. Good! Now
1265𓀴here let me say frankly, lest you should think it strange that I have
1266𓀴sought the services of one so far off from London instead of some one
1267𓀴resident there, that my motive was that no local interest might be
1268𓀴served save my wish only; and as one of London residence might, perhaps,
1269𓀴have some purpose of himself or friend to serve, I went thus afield to
1270𓀴seek my agent, whose labours should be only to my interest. Now, suppose
1271𓀴I, who have much of affairs, wish to ship goods, say, to Newcastle, or
1272𓀴Durham, or Harwich, or Dover, might it not be that it could with more
1273𓀴ease be done by consigning to one in these ports?” I answered that
1274𓀴certainly it would be most easy, but that we solicitors had a system of
1275𓀴agency one for the other, so that local work could be done locally on
1276𓀴instruction from any solicitor, so that the client, simply placing
1277𓀴himself in the hands of one man, could have his wishes carried out by
1278𓀴him without further trouble.
1279𓀴
1280𓀴“But,” said he, “I could be at liberty to direct myself. Is it not so?”
1281𓀴
1282𓀴“Of course,” I replied; and “such is often done by men of business, who
1283𓀴do not like the whole of their affairs to be known by any one person.”
1284𓀴
1285𓀴“Good!” he said, and then went on to ask about the means of making
1286𓀴consignments and the forms to be gone through, and of all sorts of
1287𓀴difficulties which might arise, but by forethought could be guarded
1288𓀴against. I explained all these things to him to the best of my ability,
1289𓀴and he certainly left me under the impression that he would have made a
1290𓀴wonderful solicitor, for there was nothing that he did not think of or
1291𓀴foresee. For a man who was never in the country, and who did not
1292𓀴evidently do much in the way of business, his knowledge and acumen were
1293𓀴wonderful. When he had satisfied himself on these points of which he had
1294𓀴spoken, and I had verified all as well as I could by the books
1295𓀴available, he suddenly stood up and said:--
1296𓀴
1297𓀴“Have you written since your first letter to our friend Mr. Peter
1298𓀴Hawkins, or to any other?” It was with some bitterness in my heart that
1299𓀴I answered that I had not, that as yet I had not seen any opportunity of
1300𓀴sending letters to anybody.
1301𓀴
1302𓀴“Then write now, my young friend,” he said, laying a heavy hand on my
1303𓀴shoulder: “write to our friend and to any other; and say, if it will
1304𓀴please you, that you shall stay with me until a month from now.”
1305𓀴
1306𓀴“Do you wish me to stay so long?” I asked, for my heart grew cold at the
1307𓀴thought.
1308𓀴
1309𓀴“I desire it much; nay, I will take no refusal. When your master,
1310𓀴employer, what you will, engaged that someone should come on his behalf,
1311𓀴it was understood that my needs only were to be consulted. I have not
1312𓀴stinted. Is it not so?”
1313𓀴
1314𓀴What could I do but bow acceptance? It was Mr. Hawkins’s interest, not
1315𓀴mine, and I had to think of him, not myself; and besides, while Count
1316𓀴Dracula was speaking, there was that in his eyes and in his bearing
1317𓀴which made me remember that I was a prisoner, and that if I wished it I
1318𓀴could have no choice. The Count saw his victory in my bow, and his
1319𓀴mastery in the trouble of my face, for he began at once to use them, but
1320𓀴in his own smooth, resistless way:--
1321𓀴
1322𓀴“I pray you, my good young friend, that you will not discourse of things
1323𓀴other than business in your letters. It will doubtless please your
1324𓀴friends to know that you are well, and that you look forward to getting
1325𓀴home to them. Is it not so?” As he spoke he handed me three sheets of
1326𓀴note-paper and three envelopes. They were all of the thinnest foreign
1327𓀴post, and looking at them, then at him, and noticing his quiet smile,
1328𓀴with the sharp, canine teeth lying over the red underlip, I understood
1329𓀴as well as if he had spoken that I should be careful what I wrote, for
1330𓀴he would be able to read it. So I determined to write only formal notes
1331𓀴now, but to write fully to Mr. Hawkins in secret, and also to Mina, for
1332𓀴to her I could write in shorthand, which would puzzle the Count, if he
1333𓀴did see it. When I had written my two letters I sat quiet, reading a
1334𓀴book whilst the Count wrote several notes, referring as he wrote them to
1335𓀴some books on his table. Then he took up my two and placed them with his
1336𓀴own, and put by his writing materials, after which, the instant the door
1337𓀴had closed behind him, I leaned over and looked at the letters, which
1338𓀴were face down on the table. I felt no compunction in doing so, for
1339𓀴under the circumstances I felt that I should protect myself in every way
1340𓀴I could.
1341𓀴
1342𓀴One of the letters was directed to Samuel F. Billington, No. 7, The
1343𓀴Crescent, Whitby, another to Herr Leutner, Varna; the third was to
1344𓀴Coutts & Co., London, and the fourth to Herren Klopstock & Billreuth,
1345𓀴bankers, Buda-Pesth. The second and fourth were unsealed. I was just
1346𓀴about to look at them when I saw the door-handle move. I sank back in my
1347𓀴seat, having just had time to replace the letters as they had been and
1348𓀴to resume my book before the Count, holding still another letter in his
1349𓀴hand, entered the room. He took up the letters on the table and stamped
1350𓀴them carefully, and then turning to me, said:--
1351𓀴
1352𓀴“I trust you will forgive me, but I have much work to do in private this
1353𓀴evening. You will, I hope, find all things as you wish.” At the door he
1354𓀴turned, and after a moment’s pause said:--
1355𓀴
1356𓀴“Let me advise you, my dear young friend--nay, let me warn you with all
1357𓀴seriousness, that should you leave these rooms you will not by any
1358𓀴chance go to sleep in any other part of the castle. It is old, and has
1359𓀴many memories, and there are bad dreams for those who sleep unwisely. Be
1360𓀴warned! Should sleep now or ever overcome you, or be like to do, then
1361𓀴haste to your own chamber or to these rooms, for your rest will then be
