1362𓀴safe. But if you be not careful in this respect, then”--He finished his
1363𓀴speech in a gruesome way, for he motioned with his hands as if he were
1364𓀴washing them. I quite understood; my only doubt was as to whether any
1365𓀴dream could be more terrible than the unnatural, horrible net of gloom
1366𓀴and mystery which seemed closing around me.
1367𓀴
1368𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
1369𓀴
1370𓀴_Later._--I endorse the last words written, but this time there is no
1371𓀴doubt in question. I shall not fear to sleep in any place where he is
1372𓀴not. I have placed the crucifix over the head of my bed--I imagine that
1373𓀴my rest is thus freer from dreams; and there it shall remain.
1374𓀴
1375𓀴When he left me I went to my room. After a little while, not hearing any
1376𓀴sound, I came out and went up the stone stair to where I could look out
1377𓀴towards the South. There was some sense of freedom in the vast expanse,
1378𓀴inaccessible though it was to me, as compared with the narrow darkness
1379𓀴of the courtyard. Looking out on this, I felt that I was indeed in
1380𓀴prison, and I seemed to want a breath of fresh air, though it were of
1381𓀴the night. I am beginning to feel this nocturnal existence tell on me.
1382𓀴It is destroying my nerve. I start at my own shadow, and am full of all
1383𓀴sorts of horrible imaginings. God knows that there is ground for my
1384𓀴terrible fear in this accursed place! I looked out over the beautiful
1385𓀴expanse, bathed in soft yellow moonlight till it was almost as light as
1386𓀴day. In the soft light the distant hills became melted, and the shadows
1387𓀴in the valleys and gorges of velvety blackness. The mere beauty seemed
1388𓀴to cheer me; there was peace and comfort in every breath I drew. As I
1389𓀴leaned from the window my eye was caught by something moving a storey
1390𓀴below me, and somewhat to my left, where I imagined, from the order of
1391𓀴the rooms, that the windows of the Count’s own room would look out. The
1392𓀴window at which I stood was tall and deep, stone-mullioned, and though
1393𓀴weatherworn, was still complete; but it was evidently many a day since
1394𓀴the case had been there. I drew back behind the stonework, and looked
1395𓀴carefully out.
1396𓀴
1397𓀴What I saw was the Count’s head coming out from the window. I did not
1398𓀴see the face, but I knew the man by the neck and the movement of his
1399𓀴back and arms. In any case I could not mistake the hands which I had had
1400𓀴so many opportunities of studying. I was at first interested and
1401𓀴somewhat amused, for it is wonderful how small a matter will interest
1402𓀴and amuse a man when he is a prisoner. But my very feelings changed to
1403𓀴repulsion and terror when I saw the whole man slowly emerge from the
1404𓀴window and begin to crawl down the castle wall over that dreadful abyss,
1405𓀴_face down_ with his cloak spreading out around him like great wings. At
1406𓀴first I could not believe my eyes. I thought it was some trick of the
1407𓀴moonlight, some weird effect of shadow; but I kept looking, and it could
1408𓀴be no delusion. I saw the fingers and toes grasp the corners of the
1409𓀴stones, worn clear of the mortar by the stress of years, and by thus
1410𓀴using every projection and inequality move downwards with considerable
1411𓀴speed, just as a lizard moves along a wall.
1412𓀴
1413𓀴What manner of man is this, or what manner of creature is it in the
1414𓀴semblance of man? I feel the dread of this horrible place overpowering
1415𓀴me; I am in fear--in awful fear--and there is no escape for me; I am
1416𓀴encompassed about with terrors that I dare not think of....
1417𓀴
1418𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
1419𓀴
1420𓀴_15 May._--Once more have I seen the Count go out in his lizard fashion.
1421𓀴He moved downwards in a sidelong way, some hundred feet down, and a good
1422𓀴deal to the left. He vanished into some hole or window. When his head
1423𓀴had disappeared, I leaned out to try and see more, but without
1424𓀴avail--the distance was too great to allow a proper angle of sight. I
1425𓀴knew he had left the castle now, and thought to use the opportunity to
1426𓀴explore more than I had dared to do as yet. I went back to the room, and
1427𓀴taking a lamp, tried all the doors. They were all locked, as I had
1428𓀴expected, and the locks were comparatively new; but I went down the
1429𓀴stone stairs to the hall where I had entered originally. I found I could
1430𓀴pull back the bolts easily enough and unhook the great chains; but the
1431𓀴door was locked, and the key was gone! That key must be in the Count’s
1432𓀴room; I must watch should his door be unlocked, so that I may get it and
1433𓀴escape. I went on to make a thorough examination of the various stairs
1434𓀴and passages, and to try the doors that opened from them. One or two
1435𓀴small rooms near the hall were open, but there was nothing to see in
1436𓀴them except old furniture, dusty with age and moth-eaten. At last,
1437𓀴however, I found one door at the top of the stairway which, though it
1438𓀴seemed to be locked, gave a little under pressure. I tried it harder,
1439𓀴and found that it was not really locked, but that the resistance came
1440𓀴from the fact that the hinges had fallen somewhat, and the heavy door
1441𓀴rested on the floor. Here was an opportunity which I might not have
1442𓀴again, so I exerted myself, and with many efforts forced it back so that
1443𓀴I could enter. I was now in a wing of the castle further to the right
1444𓀴than the rooms I knew and a storey lower down. From the windows I could
1445𓀴see that the suite of rooms lay along to the south of the castle, the
1446𓀴windows of the end room looking out both west and south. On the latter
1447𓀴side, as well as to the former, there was a great precipice. The castle
1448𓀴was built on the corner of a great rock, so that on three sides it was
1449𓀴quite impregnable, and great windows were placed here where sling, or
1450𓀴bow, or culverin could not reach, and consequently light and comfort,
1451𓀴impossible to a position which had to be guarded, were secured. To the
1452𓀴west was a great valley, and then, rising far away, great jagged
1453𓀴mountain fastnesses, rising peak on peak, the sheer rock studded with
1454𓀴mountain ash and thorn, whose roots clung in cracks and crevices and
1455𓀴crannies of the stone. This was evidently the portion of the castle
1456𓀴occupied by the ladies in bygone days, for the furniture had more air of
1457𓀴comfort than any I had seen. The windows were curtainless, and the
1458𓀴yellow moonlight, flooding in through the diamond panes, enabled one to
1459𓀴see even colours, whilst it softened the wealth of dust which lay over
1460𓀴all and disguised in some measure the ravages of time and the moth. My
1461𓀴lamp seemed to be of little effect in the brilliant moonlight, but I was
1462𓀴glad to have it with me, for there was a dread loneliness in the place
1463𓀴which chilled my heart and made my nerves tremble. Still, it was better
1464𓀴than living alone in the rooms which I had come to hate from the
1465𓀴presence of the Count, and after trying a little to school my nerves, I
1466𓀴found a soft quietude come over me. Here I am, sitting at a little oak
1467𓀴table where in old times possibly some fair lady sat to pen, with much
1468𓀴thought and many blushes, her ill-spelt love-letter, and writing in my
1469𓀴diary in shorthand all that has happened since I closed it last. It is
1470𓀴nineteenth century up-to-date with a vengeance. And yet, unless my
1471𓀴senses deceive me, the old centuries had, and have, powers of their own
1472𓀴which mere “modernity” cannot kill.
1473𓀴
1474𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
1475𓀴
1476𓀴_Later: the Morning of 16 May._--God preserve my sanity, for to this I
1477𓀴am reduced. Safety and the assurance of safety are things of the past.
1478𓀴Whilst I live on here there is but one thing to hope for, that I may not
1479𓀴go mad, if, indeed, I be not mad already. If I be sane, then surely it
1480𓀴is maddening to think that of all the foul things that lurk in this
1481𓀴hateful place the Count is the least dreadful to me; that to him alone I
1482𓀴can look for safety, even though this be only whilst I can serve his
1483𓀴purpose. Great God! merciful God! Let me be calm, for out of that way
1484𓀴lies madness indeed. I begin to get new lights on certain things which
1485𓀴have puzzled me. Up to now I never quite knew what Shakespeare meant
1486𓀴when he made Hamlet say:--
1487𓀴
1488𓀴    “My tablets! quick, my tablets!
1489𓀴    ’Tis meet that I put it down,” etc.,
1490𓀴
1491𓀴for now, feeling as though my own brain were unhinged or as if the shock
1492𓀴had come which must end in its undoing, I turn to my diary for repose.
1493𓀴The habit of entering accurately must help to soothe me.
1494𓀴
1495𓀴The Count’s mysterious warning frightened me at the time; it frightens
1496𓀴me more now when I think of it, for in future he has a fearful hold upon
1497𓀴me. I shall fear to doubt what he may say!
1498𓀴
1499𓀴When I had written in my diary and had fortunately replaced the book and
1500𓀴pen in my pocket I felt sleepy. The Count’s warning came into my mind,
1501𓀴but I took a pleasure in disobeying it. The sense of sleep was upon me,
1502𓀴and with it the obstinacy which sleep brings as outrider. The soft
1503𓀴moonlight soothed, and the wide expanse without gave a sense of freedom
1504𓀴which refreshed me. I determined not to return to-night to the
1505𓀴gloom-haunted rooms, but to sleep here, where, of old, ladies had sat
1506𓀴and sung and lived sweet lives whilst their gentle breasts were sad for
1507𓀴their menfolk away in the midst of remorseless wars. I drew a great
1508𓀴couch out of its place near the corner, so that as I lay, I could look
1509𓀴at the lovely view to east and south, and unthinking of and uncaring for
1510𓀴the dust, composed myself for sleep. I suppose I must have fallen
1511𓀴asleep; I hope so, but I fear, for all that followed was startlingly
1512𓀴real--so real that now sitting here in the broad, full sunlight of the
1513𓀴morning, I cannot in the least believe that it was all sleep.
1514𓀴
1515𓀴I was not alone. The room was the same, unchanged in any way since I
1516𓀴came into it; I could see along the floor, in the brilliant moonlight,
1517𓀴my own footsteps marked where I had disturbed the long accumulation of
1518𓀴dust. In the moonlight opposite me were three young women, ladies by
1519𓀴their dress and manner. I thought at the time that I must be dreaming
1520𓀴when I saw them, for, though the moonlight was behind them, they threw
1521𓀴no shadow on the floor. They came close to me, and looked at me for some
1522𓀴time, and then whispered together. Two were dark, and had high aquiline
1523𓀴noses, like the Count, and great dark, piercing eyes that seemed to be
1524𓀴almost red when contrasted with the pale yellow moon. The other was
1525𓀴fair, as fair as can be, with great wavy masses of golden hair and eyes
1526𓀴like pale sapphires. I seemed somehow to know her face, and to know it
1527𓀴in connection with some dreamy fear, but I could not recollect at the
1528𓀴moment how or where. All three had brilliant white teeth that shone like
1529𓀴pearls against the ruby of their voluptuous lips. There was something
1530𓀴about them that made me uneasy, some longing and at the same time some
1531𓀴deadly fear. I felt in my heart a wicked, burning desire that they would
1532𓀴kiss me with those red lips. It is not good to note this down, lest some
1533𓀴day it should meet Mina’s eyes and cause her pain; but it is the truth.
1534𓀴They whispered together, and then they all three laughed--such a
1535𓀴silvery, musical laugh, but as hard as though the sound never could have
1536𓀴come through the softness of human lips. It was like the intolerable,
1537𓀴tingling sweetness of water-glasses when played on by a cunning hand.
1538𓀴The fair girl shook her head coquettishly, and the other two urged her
1539𓀴on. One said:--
1540𓀴
1541𓀴“Go on! You are first, and we shall follow; yours is the right to
1542𓀴begin.” The other added:--
1543𓀴
1544𓀴“He is young and strong; there are kisses for us all.” I lay quiet,
1545𓀴looking out under my eyelashes in an agony of delightful anticipation.
1546𓀴The fair girl advanced and bent over me till I could feel the movement
1547𓀴of her breath upon me. Sweet it was in one sense, honey-sweet, and sent
1548𓀴the same tingling through the nerves as her voice, but with a bitter
1549𓀴underlying the sweet, a bitter offensiveness, as one smells in blood.
1550𓀴
1551𓀴I was afraid to raise my eyelids, but looked out and saw perfectly under
1552𓀴the lashes. The girl went on her knees, and bent over me, simply
1553𓀴gloating. There was a deliberate voluptuousness which was both thrilling
1554𓀴and repulsive, and as she arched her neck she actually licked her lips
1555𓀴like an animal, till I could see in the moonlight the moisture shining
1556𓀴on the scarlet lips and on the red tongue as it lapped the white sharp
1557𓀴teeth. Lower and lower went her head as the lips went below the range of
1558𓀴my mouth and chin and seemed about to fasten on my throat. Then she
1559𓀴paused, and I could hear the churning sound of her tongue as it licked
1560𓀴her teeth and lips, and could feel the hot breath on my neck. Then the
1561𓀴skin of my throat began to tingle as one’s flesh does when the hand that
1562𓀴is to tickle it approaches nearer--nearer. I could feel the soft,
1563𓀴shivering touch of the lips on the super-sensitive skin of my throat,
1564𓀴and the hard dents of two sharp teeth, just touching and pausing there.
1565𓀴I closed my eyes in a languorous ecstasy and waited--waited with beating
1566𓀴heart.
1567𓀴
1568𓀴But at that instant, another sensation swept through me as quick as
1569𓀴lightning. I was conscious of the presence of the Count, and of his
1570𓀴being as if lapped in a storm of fury. As my eyes opened involuntarily I
1571𓀴saw his strong hand grasp the slender neck of the fair woman and with
1572𓀴giant’s power draw it back, the blue eyes transformed with fury, the
1573𓀴white teeth champing with rage, and the fair cheeks blazing red with
1574𓀴passion. But the Count! Never did I imagine such wrath and fury, even to
1575𓀴the demons of the pit. His eyes were positively blazing. The red light
1576𓀴in them was lurid, as if the flames of hell-fire blazed behind them. His
1577𓀴face was deathly pale, and the lines of it were hard like drawn wires;
1578𓀴the thick eyebrows that met over the nose now seemed like a heaving bar
1579𓀴of white-hot metal. With a fierce sweep of his arm, he hurled the woman
1580𓀴from him, and then motioned to the others, as though he were beating
1581𓀴them back; it was the same imperious gesture that I had seen used to the
1582𓀴wolves. In a voice which, though low and almost in a whisper seemed to
1583𓀴cut through the air and then ring round the room he said:--
1584𓀴
1585𓀴“How dare you touch him, any of you? How dare you cast eyes on him when
1586𓀴I had forbidden it? Back, I tell you all! This man belongs to me! Beware
1587𓀴how you meddle with him, or you’ll have to deal with me.” The fair girl,
1588𓀴with a laugh of ribald coquetry, turned to answer him:--
1589𓀴
1590𓀴“You yourself never loved; you never love!” On this the other women
1591𓀴joined, and such a mirthless, hard, soulless laughter rang through the
1592𓀴room that it almost made me faint to hear; it seemed like the pleasure
1593𓀴of fiends. Then the Count turned, after looking at my face attentively,
1594𓀴and said in a soft whisper:--
1595𓀴
1596𓀴“Yes, I too can love; you yourselves can tell it from the past. Is it
1597𓀴not so? Well, now I promise you that when I am done with him you shall
1598𓀴kiss him at your will. Now go! go! I must awaken him, for there is work
1599𓀴to be done.”
1600𓀴
1601𓀴“Are we to have nothing to-night?” said one of them, with a low laugh,
1602𓀴as she pointed to the bag which he had thrown upon the floor, and which
1603𓀴moved as though there were some living thing within it. For answer he
1604𓀴nodded his head. One of the women jumped forward and opened it. If my
1605𓀴ears did not deceive me there was a gasp and a low wail, as of a
1606𓀴half-smothered child. The women closed round, whilst I was aghast with
1607𓀴horror; but as I looked they disappeared, and with them the dreadful
1608𓀴bag. There was no door near them, and they could not have passed me
1609𓀴without my noticing. They simply seemed to fade into the rays of the
1610𓀴moonlight and pass out through the window, for I could see outside the
1611𓀴dim, shadowy forms for a moment before they entirely faded away.
1612𓀴
1613𓀴Then the horror overcame me, and I sank down unconscious.
1614𓀴
1615𓀴
1616𓀴
1617𓀴
1618𓀴CHAPTER IV
1619𓀴
1620𓀴JONATHAN HARKER’S JOURNAL--_continued_
1621𓀴
1622𓀴
1623𓀴I awoke in my own bed. If it be that I had not dreamt, the Count must
1624𓀴have carried me here. I tried to satisfy myself on the subject, but
1625𓀴could not arrive at any unquestionable result. To be sure, there were
1626𓀴certain small evidences, such as that my clothes were folded and laid by
1627𓀴in a manner which was not my habit. My watch was still unwound, and I am
1628𓀴rigorously accustomed to wind it the last thing before going to bed, and
1629𓀴many such details. But these things are no proof, for they may have been
1630𓀴evidences that my mind was not as usual, and, from some cause or
1631𓀴another, I had certainly been much upset. I must watch for proof. Of one
1632𓀴thing I am glad: if it was that the Count carried me here and undressed
1633𓀴me, he must have been hurried in his task, for my pockets are intact. I
1634𓀴am sure this diary would have been a mystery to him which he would not
1635𓀴have brooked. He would have taken or destroyed it. As I look round this
1636𓀴room, although it has been to me so full of fear, it is now a sort of
1637𓀴sanctuary, for nothing can be more dreadful than those awful women, who
1638𓀴were--who _are_--waiting to suck my blood.
1639𓀴
1640𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
1641𓀴
1642𓀴_18 May._--I have been down to look at that room again in daylight, for
1643𓀴I _must_ know the truth. When I got to the doorway at the top of the
1644𓀴stairs I found it closed. It had been so forcibly driven against the
1645𓀴jamb that part of the woodwork was splintered. I could see that the bolt
1646𓀴of the lock had not been shot, but the door is fastened from the inside.
1647𓀴I fear it was no dream, and must act on this surmise.
1648𓀴
1649𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
1650𓀴
1651𓀴_19 May._--I am surely in the toils. Last night the Count asked me in
1652𓀴the suavest tones to write three letters, one saying that my work here
1653𓀴was nearly done, and that I should start for home within a few days,
1654𓀴another that I was starting on the next morning from the time of the
1655𓀴letter, and the third that I had left the castle and arrived at
1656𓀴Bistritz. I would fain have rebelled, but felt that in the present state
1657𓀴of things it would be madness to quarrel openly with the Count whilst I
1658𓀴am so absolutely in his power; and to refuse would be to excite his
1659𓀴suspicion and to arouse his anger. He knows that I know too much, and
1660𓀴that I must not live, lest I be dangerous to him; my only chance is to
1661𓀴prolong my opportunities. Something may occur which will give me a
1662𓀴chance to escape. I saw in his eyes something of that gathering wrath
1663𓀴which was manifest when he hurled that fair woman from him. He explained
1664𓀴to me that posts were few and uncertain, and that my writing now would
1665𓀴ensure ease of mind to my friends; and he assured me with so much
1666𓀴impressiveness that he would countermand the later letters, which would
1667𓀴be held over at Bistritz until due time in case chance would admit of my
1668𓀴prolonging my stay, that to oppose him would have been to create new
1669𓀴suspicion. I therefore pretended to fall in with his views, and asked
1670𓀴him what dates I should put on the letters. He calculated a minute, and
1671𓀴then said:--
1672𓀴
1673𓀴“The first should be June 12, the second June 19, and the third June
1674𓀴29.”
1675𓀴
1676𓀴I know now the span of my life. God help me!
1677𓀴
1678𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
1679𓀴
1680𓀴_28 May._--There is a chance of escape, or at any rate of being able to
1681𓀴send word home. A band of Szgany have come to the castle, and are
1682𓀴encamped in the courtyard. These Szgany are gipsies; I have notes of
1683𓀴them in my book. They are peculiar to this part of the world, though
1684𓀴allied to the ordinary gipsies all the world over. There are thousands
1685𓀴of them in Hungary and Transylvania, who are almost outside all law.
1686𓀴They attach themselves as a rule to some great noble or _boyar_, and
1687𓀴call themselves by his name. They are fearless and without religion,
1688𓀴save superstition, and they talk only their own varieties of the Romany
1689𓀴tongue.
1690𓀴
1691𓀴I shall write some letters home, and shall try to get them to have them
1692𓀴posted. I have already spoken them through my window to begin
1693𓀴acquaintanceship. They took their hats off and made obeisance and many
1694𓀴signs, which, however, I could not understand any more than I could
1695𓀴their spoken language....
1696𓀴
1697𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
1698𓀴
1699𓀴I have written the letters. Mina’s is in shorthand, and I simply ask Mr.
1700𓀴Hawkins to communicate with her. To her I have explained my situation,
1701𓀴but without the horrors which I may only surmise. It would shock and
1702𓀴frighten her to death were I to expose my heart to her. Should the
1703𓀴letters not carry, then the Count shall not yet know my secret or the
1704𓀴extent of my knowledge....
1705𓀴
1706𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
1707𓀴
1708𓀴I have given the letters; I threw them through the bars of my window
1709𓀴with a gold piece, and made what signs I could to have them posted. The
1710𓀴man who took them pressed them to his heart and bowed, and then put them
1711𓀴in his cap. I could do no more. I stole back to the study, and began to
1712𓀴read. As the Count did not come in, I have written here....
1713𓀴
1714𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
1715𓀴
1716𓀴The Count has come. He sat down beside me, and said in his smoothest
1717𓀴voice as he opened two letters:--
1718𓀴
1719𓀴“The Szgany has given me these, of which, though I know not whence they
1720𓀴come, I shall, of course, take care. See!”--he must have looked at
1721𓀴it--“one is from you, and to my friend Peter Hawkins; the other”--here
1722𓀴he caught sight of the strange symbols as he opened the envelope, and
1723𓀴the dark look came into his face, and his eyes blazed wickedly--“the
1724𓀴other is a vile thing, an outrage upon friendship and hospitality! It is
1725𓀴not signed. Well! so it cannot matter to us.” And he calmly held letter
1726𓀴and envelope in the flame of the lamp till they were consumed. Then he
1727𓀴went on:--
1728𓀴
1729𓀴“The letter to Hawkins--that I shall, of course, send on, since it is
1730𓀴yours. Your letters are sacred to me. Your pardon, my friend, that
1731𓀴unknowingly I did break the seal. Will you not cover it again?” He held
1732𓀴out the letter to me, and with a courteous bow handed me a clean
1733𓀴envelope. I could only redirect it and hand it to him in silence. When
1734𓀴he went out of the room I could hear the key turn softly. A minute later
1735𓀴I went over and tried it, and the door was locked.
1736𓀴
1737𓀴When, an hour or two after, the Count came quietly into the room, his
1738𓀴coming awakened me, for I had gone to sleep on the sofa. He was very
1739𓀴courteous and very cheery in his manner, and seeing that I had been
1740𓀴sleeping, he said:--
1741𓀴
1742𓀴“So, my friend, you are tired? Get to bed. There is the surest rest. I
1743𓀴may not have the pleasure to talk to-night, since there are many labours
1744𓀴to me; but you will sleep, I pray.” I passed to my room and went to bed,
1745𓀴and, strange to say, slept without dreaming. Despair has its own calms.
1746𓀴
1747𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
1748𓀴
1749𓀴_31 May._--This morning when I woke I thought I would provide myself
1750𓀴with some paper and envelopes from my bag and keep them in my pocket, so
1751𓀴that I might write in case I should get an opportunity, but again a
1752𓀴surprise, again a shock!
1753𓀴
1754𓀴Every scrap of paper was gone, and with it all my notes, my memoranda,
1755𓀴relating to railways and travel, my letter of credit, in fact all that
1756𓀴might be useful to me were I once outside the castle. I sat and pondered
1757𓀴awhile, and then some thought occurred to me, and I made search of my
1758𓀴portmanteau and in the wardrobe where I had placed my clothes.
1759𓀴
1760𓀴The suit in which I had travelled was gone, and also my overcoat and
1761𓀴rug; I could find no trace of them anywhere. This looked like some new
1762𓀴scheme of villainy....
1763𓀴
1764𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
1765𓀴
1766𓀴_17 June._--This morning, as I was sitting on the edge of my bed
1767𓀴cudgelling my brains, I heard without a cracking of whips and pounding
1768𓀴and scraping of horses’ feet up the rocky path beyond the courtyard.
1769𓀴With joy I hurried to the window, and saw drive into the yard two great
1770𓀴leiter-wagons, each drawn by eight sturdy horses, and at the head of
1771𓀴each pair a Slovak, with his wide hat, great nail-studded belt, dirty
1772𓀴sheepskin, and high boots. They had also their long staves in hand. I
1773𓀴ran to the door, intending to descend and try and join them through the
1774𓀴main hall, as I thought that way might be opened for them. Again a
1775𓀴shock: my door was fastened on the outside.
1776𓀴
1777𓀴Then I ran to the window and cried to them. They looked up at me
1778𓀴stupidly and pointed, but just then the “hetman” of the Szgany came out,
1779𓀴and seeing them pointing to my window, said something, at which they
1780𓀴laughed. Henceforth no effort of mine, no piteous cry or agonised
1781𓀴entreaty, would make them even look at me. They resolutely turned away.
1782𓀴The leiter-wagons contained great, square boxes, with handles of thick
1783𓀴rope; these were evidently empty by the ease with which the Slovaks
1784𓀴handled them, and by their resonance as they were roughly moved. When
1785𓀴they were all unloaded and packed in a great heap in one corner of the
1786𓀴yard, the Slovaks were given some money by the Szgany, and spitting on
1787𓀴it for luck, lazily went each to his horse’s head. Shortly afterwards, I
1788𓀴heard the cracking of their whips die away in the distance.
1789𓀴
1790𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
1791𓀴
1792𓀴_24 June, before morning._--Last night the Count left me early, and
1793𓀴locked himself into his own room. As soon as I dared I ran up the
1794𓀴winding stair, and looked out of the window, which opened south. I
1795𓀴thought I would watch for the Count, for there is something going on.
1796𓀴The Szgany are quartered somewhere in the castle and are doing work of
1797𓀴some kind. I know it, for now and then I hear a far-away muffled sound
1798𓀴as of mattock and spade, and, whatever it is, it must be the end of some
1799𓀴ruthless villainy.
1800𓀴
1801𓀴I had been at the window somewhat less than half an hour, when I saw
1802𓀴something coming out of the Count’s window. I drew back and watched
1803𓀴carefully, and saw the whole man emerge. It was a new shock to me to
1804𓀴find that he had on the suit of clothes which I had worn whilst
1805𓀴travelling here, and slung over his shoulder the terrible bag which I
1806𓀴had seen the women take away. There could be no doubt as to his quest,
1807𓀴and in my garb, too! This, then, is his new scheme of evil: that he will
1808𓀴allow others to see me, as they think, so that he may both leave
1809𓀴evidence that I have been seen in the towns or villages posting my own
1810𓀴letters, and that any wickedness which he may do shall by the local
1811𓀴people be attributed to me.
1812𓀴
1813𓀴It makes me rage to think that this can go on, and whilst I am shut up
1814𓀴here, a veritable prisoner, but without that protection of the law which
1815𓀴is even a criminal’s right and consolation.
1816𓀴
1817𓀴I thought I would watch for the Count’s return, and for a long time sat
1818𓀴doggedly at the window. Then I began to notice that there were some
1819𓀴quaint little specks floating in the rays of the moonlight. They were
1820𓀴like the tiniest grains of dust, and they whirled round and gathered in
1821𓀴clusters in a nebulous sort of way. I watched them with a sense of
1822𓀴soothing, and a sort of calm stole over me. I leaned back in the
1823𓀴embrasure in a more comfortable position, so that I could enjoy more
1824𓀴fully the aërial gambolling.
1825𓀴
1826𓀴Something made me start up, a low, piteous howling of dogs somewhere far
1827𓀴below in the valley, which was hidden from my sight. Louder it seemed to
1828𓀴ring in my ears, and the floating motes of dust to take new shapes to
1829𓀴the sound as they danced in the moonlight. I felt myself struggling to
1830𓀴awake to some call of my instincts; nay, my very soul was struggling,
1831𓀴and my half-remembered sensibilities were striving to answer the call. I
1832𓀴was becoming hypnotised! Quicker and quicker danced the dust; the
1833𓀴moonbeams seemed to quiver as they went by me into the mass of gloom
1834𓀴beyond. More and more they gathered till they seemed to take dim phantom
1835𓀴shapes. And then I started, broad awake and in full possession of my
1836𓀴senses, and ran screaming from the place. The phantom shapes, which were
1837𓀴becoming gradually materialised from the moonbeams, were those of the
1838𓀴three ghostly women to whom I was doomed. I fled, and felt somewhat
1839𓀴safer in my own room, where there was no moonlight and where the lamp
1840𓀴was burning brightly.
1841𓀴
1842𓀴When a couple of hours had passed I heard something stirring in the
1843𓀴Count’s room, something like a sharp wail quickly suppressed; and then
1844𓀴there was silence, deep, awful silence, which chilled me. With a
1845𓀴beating heart, I tried the door; but I was locked in my prison, and
1846𓀴could do nothing. I sat down and simply cried.
1847𓀴
1848𓀴As I sat I heard a sound in the courtyard without--the agonised cry of a
1849𓀴woman. I rushed to the window, and throwing it up, peered out between
1850𓀴the bars. There, indeed, was a woman with dishevelled hair, holding her
1851𓀴hands over her heart as one distressed with running. She was leaning
1852𓀴against a corner of the gateway. When she saw my face at the window she
1853𓀴threw herself forward, and shouted in a voice laden with menace:--
1854𓀴
1855𓀴“Monster, give me my child!”
1856𓀴
1857𓀴She threw herself on her knees, and raising up her hands, cried the same
1858𓀴words in tones which wrung my heart. Then she tore her hair and beat her
1859𓀴breast, and abandoned herself to all the violences of extravagant
1860𓀴emotion. Finally, she threw herself forward, and, though I could not see
1861𓀴her, I could hear the beating of her naked hands against the door.
1862𓀴
1863𓀴Somewhere high overhead, probably on the tower, I heard the voice of the
1864𓀴Count calling in his harsh, metallic whisper. His call seemed to be
1865𓀴answered from far and wide by the howling of wolves. Before many minutes
1866𓀴had passed a pack of them poured, like a pent-up dam when liberated,
1867𓀴through the wide entrance into the courtyard.
1868𓀴
1869𓀴There was no cry from the woman, and the howling of the wolves was but
1870𓀴short. Before long they streamed away singly, licking their lips.
1871𓀴
1872𓀴I could not pity her, for I knew now what had become of her child, and
1873𓀴she was better dead.
1874𓀴
1875𓀴What shall I do? what can I do? How can I escape from this dreadful
1876𓀴thing of night and gloom and fear?
1877𓀴
1878𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
1879𓀴
1880𓀴_25 June, morning._--No man knows till he has suffered from the night
1881𓀴how sweet and how dear to his heart and eye the morning can be. When the
1882𓀴sun grew so high this morning that it struck the top of the great
1883𓀴gateway opposite my window, the high spot which it touched seemed to me
1884𓀴as if the dove from the ark had lighted there. My fear fell from me as
1885𓀴if it had been a vaporous garment which dissolved in the warmth. I must
1886𓀴take action of some sort whilst the courage of the day is upon me. Last
1887𓀴night one of my post-dated letters went to post, the first of that fatal
1888𓀴series which is to blot out the very traces of my existence from the
1889𓀴earth.
1890𓀴
1891𓀴Let me not think of it. Action!
1892𓀴
1893𓀴It has always been at night-time that I have been molested or
1894𓀴threatened, or in some way in danger or in fear. I have not yet seen the
1895𓀴Count in the daylight. Can it be that he sleeps when others wake, that
1896𓀴he may be awake whilst they sleep? If I could only get into his room!
1897𓀴But there is no possible way. The door is always locked, no way for me.
1898𓀴
1899𓀴Yes, there is a way, if one dares to take it. Where his body has gone
1900𓀴why may not another body go? I have seen him myself crawl from his
1901𓀴window. Why should not I imitate him, and go in by his window? The
1902𓀴chances are desperate, but my need is more desperate still. I shall risk
1903𓀴it. At the worst it can only be death; and a man’s death is not a
1904𓀴calf’s, and the dreaded Hereafter may still be open to me. God help me
1905𓀴in my task! Good-bye, Mina, if I fail; good-bye, my faithful friend and
1906𓀴second father; good-bye, all, and last of all Mina!
1907𓀴
1908𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
1909𓀴
1910𓀴_Same day, later._--I have made the effort, and God, helping me, have
1911𓀴come safely back to this room. I must put down every detail in order. I
1912𓀴went whilst my courage was fresh straight to the window on the south
1913𓀴side, and at once got outside on the narrow ledge of stone which runs
1914𓀴around the building on this side. The stones are big and roughly cut,
1915𓀴and the mortar has by process of time been washed away between them. I
1916𓀴took off my boots, and ventured out on the desperate way. I looked down
1917𓀴once, so as to make sure that a sudden glimpse of the awful depth would
1918𓀴not overcome me, but after that kept my eyes away from it. I knew pretty
1919𓀴well the direction and distance of the Count’s window, and made for it
1920𓀴as well as I could, having regard to the opportunities available. I did
1921𓀴not feel dizzy--I suppose I was too excited--and the time seemed
1922𓀴ridiculously short till I found myself standing on the window-sill and
1923𓀴trying to raise up the sash. I was filled with agitation, however, when
1924𓀴I bent down and slid feet foremost in through the window. Then I looked
1925𓀴around for the Count, but, with surprise and gladness, made a discovery.
1926𓀴The room was empty! It was barely furnished with odd things, which
1927𓀴seemed to have never been used; the furniture was something the same
1928𓀴style as that in the south rooms, and was covered with dust. I looked
1929𓀴for the key, but it was not in the lock, and I could not find it
1930𓀴anywhere. The only thing I found was a great heap of gold in one
1931𓀴corner--gold of all kinds, Roman, and British, and Austrian, and
1932𓀴Hungarian, and Greek and Turkish money, covered with a film of dust, as
1933𓀴though it had lain long in the ground. None of it that I noticed was
1934𓀴less than three hundred years old. There were also chains and ornaments,
1935𓀴some jewelled, but all of them old and stained.
1936𓀴
1937𓀴At one corner of the room was a heavy door. I tried it, for, since I
1938𓀴could not find the key of the room or the key of the outer door, which
1939𓀴was the main object of my search, I must make further examination, or
1940𓀴all my efforts would be in vain. It was open, and led through a stone
1941𓀴passage to a circular stairway, which went steeply down. I descended,
1942𓀴minding carefully where I went, for the stairs were dark, being only lit
1943𓀴by loopholes in the heavy masonry. At the bottom there was a dark,
1944𓀴tunnel-like passage, through which came a deathly, sickly odour, the
1945𓀴odour of old earth newly turned. As I went through the passage the smell
1946𓀴grew closer and heavier. At last I pulled open a heavy door which stood
1947𓀴ajar, and found myself in an old, ruined chapel, which had evidently
1948𓀴been used as a graveyard. The roof was broken, and in two places were
1949𓀴steps leading to vaults, but the ground had recently been dug over, and
1950𓀴the earth placed in great wooden boxes, manifestly those which had been
1951𓀴brought by the Slovaks. There was nobody about, and I made search for
1952𓀴any further outlet, but there was none. Then I went over every inch of
1953𓀴the ground, so as not to lose a chance. I went down even into the
1954𓀴vaults, where the dim light struggled, although to do so was a dread to
1955𓀴my very soul. Into two of these I went, but saw nothing except fragments
1956𓀴of old coffins and piles of dust; in the third, however, I made a
1957𓀴discovery.
1958𓀴
1959𓀴There, in one of the great boxes, of which there were fifty in all, on a
1960𓀴pile of newly dug earth, lay the Count! He was either dead or asleep, I
1961𓀴could not say which--for the eyes were open and stony, but without the
1962𓀴glassiness of death--and the cheeks had the warmth of life through all
1963𓀴their pallor; the lips were as red as ever. But there was no sign of
1964𓀴movement, no pulse, no breath, no beating of the heart. I bent over him,
1965𓀴and tried to find any sign of life, but in vain. He could not have lain
1966𓀴there long, for the earthy smell would have passed away in a few hours.
1967𓀴By the side of the box was its cover, pierced with holes here and there.
1968𓀴I thought he might have the keys on him, but when I went to search I saw
1969𓀴the dead eyes, and in them, dead though they were, such a look of hate,
1970𓀴though unconscious of me or my presence, that I fled from the place, and
1971𓀴leaving the Count’s room by the window, crawled again up the castle
1972𓀴wall. Regaining my room, I threw myself panting upon the bed and tried
1973𓀴to think....
1974𓀴
1975𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
1976𓀴
1977𓀴_29 June._--To-day is the date of my last letter, and the Count has
1978𓀴taken steps to prove that it was genuine, for again I saw him leave the
1979𓀴castle by the same window, and in my clothes. As he went down the wall,
1980𓀴lizard fashion, I wished I had a gun or some lethal weapon, that I might
1981𓀴destroy him; but I fear that no weapon wrought alone by man’s hand would
1982𓀴have any effect on him. I dared not wait to see him return, for I feared
1983𓀴to see those weird sisters. I came back to the library, and read there
1984𓀴till I fell asleep.
1985𓀴
1986𓀴I was awakened by the Count, who looked at me as grimly as a man can
1987𓀴look as he said:--
1988𓀴
1989𓀴“To-morrow, my friend, we must part. You return to your beautiful
1990𓀴England, I to some work which may have such an end that we may never
1991𓀴meet. Your letter home has been despatched; to-morrow I shall not be
1992𓀴here, but all shall be ready for your journey. In the morning come the
1993𓀴Szgany, who have some labours of their own here, and also come some
1994𓀴Slovaks. When they have gone, my carriage shall come for you, and shall
1995𓀴bear you to the Borgo Pass to meet the diligence from Bukovina to
1996𓀴Bistritz. But I am in hopes that I shall see more of you at Castle
1997𓀴Dracula.” I suspected him, and determined to test his sincerity.
1998𓀴Sincerity! It seems like a profanation of the word to write it in
1999𓀴connection with such a monster, so asked him point-blank:--
2000𓀴
2001𓀴“Why may I not go to-night?”
2002𓀴
2003𓀴“Because, dear sir, my coachman and horses are away on a mission.”
2004𓀴
2005𓀴“But I would walk with pleasure. I want to get away at once.” He smiled,
2006𓀴such a soft, smooth, diabolical smile that I knew there was some trick
2007𓀴behind his smoothness. He said:--
2008𓀴
2009𓀴“And your baggage?”
2010𓀴
2011𓀴“I do not care about it. I can send for it some other time.”
2012𓀴
2013𓀴The Count stood up, and said, with a sweet courtesy which made me rub my
2014𓀴eyes, it seemed so real:--
2015𓀴
2016𓀴“You English have a saying which is close to my heart, for its spirit is
2017𓀴that which rules our _boyars_: ‘Welcome the coming; speed the parting
2018𓀴guest.’ Come with me, my dear young friend. Not an hour shall you wait
2019𓀴in my house against your will, though sad am I at your going, and that
2020𓀴you so suddenly desire it. Come!” With a stately gravity, he, with the
2021𓀴lamp, preceded me down the stairs and along the hall. Suddenly he
2022𓀴stopped.
2023𓀴
2024𓀴“Hark!”
2025𓀴
2026𓀴Close at hand came the howling of many wolves. It was almost as if the
2027𓀴sound sprang up at the rising of his hand, just as the music of a great
2028𓀴orchestra seems to leap under the bâton of the conductor. After a pause
2029𓀴of a moment, he proceeded, in his stately way, to the door, drew back
2030𓀴the ponderous bolts, unhooked the heavy chains, and began to draw it
2031𓀴open.
2032𓀴
2033𓀴To my intense astonishment I saw that it was unlocked. Suspiciously, I
2034𓀴looked all round, but could see no key of any kind.
2035𓀴
2036𓀴As the door began to open, the howling of the wolves without grew louder
2037𓀴and angrier; their red jaws, with champing teeth, and their blunt-clawed
2038𓀴feet as they leaped, came in through the opening door. I knew then that
2039𓀴to struggle at the moment against the Count was useless. With such
2040𓀴allies as these at his command, I could do nothing. But still the door
2041𓀴continued slowly to open, and only the Count’s body stood in the gap.
2042𓀴Suddenly it struck me that this might be the moment and means of my
2043𓀴doom; I was to be given to the wolves, and at my own instigation. There
2044𓀴was a diabolical wickedness in the idea great enough for the Count, and
2045𓀴as a last chance I cried out:--
2046𓀴
2047𓀴“Shut the door; I shall wait till morning!” and covered my face with my
2048𓀴hands to hide my tears of bitter disappointment. With one sweep of his
2049𓀴powerful arm, the Count threw the door shut, and the great bolts clanged
2050𓀴and echoed through the hall as they shot back into their places.
2051𓀴
2052𓀴In silence we returned to the library, and after a minute or two I went
2053𓀴to my own room. The last I saw of Count Dracula was his kissing his hand
2054𓀴to me; with a red light of triumph in his eyes, and with a smile that
2055𓀴Judas in hell might be proud of.
2056𓀴
2057𓀴When I was in my room and about to lie down, I thought I heard a
2058𓀴whispering at my door. I went to it softly and listened. Unless my ears
2059𓀴deceived me, I heard the voice of the Count:--
2060𓀴
2061𓀴“Back, back, to your own place! Your time is not yet come. Wait! Have
2062𓀴patience! To-night is mine. To-morrow night is yours!” There was a low,
2063𓀴sweet ripple of laughter, and in a rage I threw open the door, and saw
2064𓀴without the three terrible women licking their lips. As I appeared they
2065𓀴all joined in a horrible laugh, and ran away.
2066𓀴
2067𓀴I came back to my room and threw myself on my knees. It is then so near
2068𓀴the end? To-morrow! to-morrow! Lord, help me, and those to whom I am
2069𓀴dear!
2070𓀴
2071𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
2072𓀴
2073𓀴_30 June, morning._--These may be the last words I ever write in this
2074𓀴diary. I slept till just before the dawn, and when I woke threw myself
2075𓀴on my knees, for I determined that if Death came he should find me
2076𓀴ready.
2077𓀴
2078𓀴At last I felt that subtle change in the air, and knew that the morning
2079𓀴had come. Then came the welcome cock-crow, and I felt that I was safe.
2080𓀴With a glad heart, I opened my door and ran down to the hall. I had seen
2081𓀴that the door was unlocked, and now escape was before me. With hands
2082𓀴that trembled with eagerness, I unhooked the chains and drew back the
2083𓀴massive bolts.
2084𓀴
2085𓀴But the door would not move. Despair seized me. I pulled, and pulled, at
2086𓀴the door, and shook it till, massive as it was, it rattled in its
2087𓀴casement. I could see the bolt shot. It had been locked after I left the
2088𓀴Count.
2089𓀴
2090𓀴Then a wild desire took me to obtain that key at any risk, and I
2091𓀴determined then and there to scale the wall again and gain the Count’s
2092𓀴room. He might kill me, but death now seemed the happier choice of
2093𓀴evils. Without a pause I rushed up to the east window, and scrambled
2094𓀴down the wall, as before, into the Count’s room. It was empty, but that
2095𓀴was as I expected. I could not see a key anywhere, but the heap of gold
2096𓀴remained. I went through the door in the corner and down the winding
2097𓀴stair and along the dark passage to the old chapel. I knew now well
2098𓀴enough where to find the monster I sought.
2099𓀴
2100𓀴The great box was in the same place, close against the wall, but the lid
2101𓀴was laid on it, not fastened down, but with the nails ready in their
2102𓀴places to be hammered home. I knew I must reach the body for the key, so
2103𓀴I raised the lid, and laid it back against the wall; and then I saw
2104𓀴something which filled my very soul with horror. There lay the Count,
2105𓀴but looking as if his youth had been half renewed, for the white hair
2106𓀴and moustache were changed to dark iron-grey; the cheeks were fuller,
2107𓀴and the white skin seemed ruby-red underneath; the mouth was redder than
2108𓀴ever, for on the lips were gouts of fresh blood, which trickled from the
2109𓀴corners of the mouth and ran over the chin and neck. Even the deep,
2110𓀴burning eyes seemed set amongst swollen flesh, for the lids and pouches
2111𓀴underneath were bloated. It seemed as if the whole awful creature were
2112𓀴simply gorged with blood. He lay like a filthy leech, exhausted with his
2113𓀴repletion. I shuddered as I bent over to touch him, and every sense in
2114𓀴me revolted at the contact; but I had to search, or I was lost. The
2115𓀴coming night might see my own body a banquet in a similar way to those
2116𓀴horrid three. I felt all over the body, but no sign could I find of the
2117𓀴key. Then I stopped and looked at the Count. There was a mocking smile
2118𓀴on the bloated face which seemed to drive me mad. This was the being I
2119𓀴was helping to transfer to London, where, perhaps, for centuries to come
2120𓀴he might, amongst its teeming millions, satiate his lust for blood, and
2121𓀴create a new and ever-widening circle of semi-demons to batten on the
2122𓀴helpless. The very thought drove me mad. A terrible desire came upon me
2123𓀴to rid the world of such a monster. There was no lethal weapon at hand,
2124𓀴but I seized a shovel which the workmen had been using to fill the
2125𓀴cases, and lifting it high, struck, with the edge downward, at the
2126𓀴hateful face. But as I did so the head turned, and the eyes fell full
2127𓀴upon me, with all their blaze of basilisk horror. The sight seemed to
2128𓀴paralyse me, and the shovel turned in my hand and glanced from the face,
2129𓀴merely making a deep gash above the forehead. The shovel fell from my
2130𓀴hand across the box, and as I pulled it away the flange of the blade
2131𓀴caught the edge of the lid which fell over again, and hid the horrid
2132𓀴thing from my sight. The last glimpse I had was of the bloated face,
2133𓀴blood-stained and fixed with a grin of malice which would have held its
2134𓀴own in the nethermost hell.
2135𓀴
2136𓀴I thought and thought what should be my next move, but my brain seemed
2137𓀴on fire, and I waited with a despairing feeling growing over me. As I
2138𓀴waited I heard in the distance a gipsy song sung by merry voices coming
2139𓀴closer, and through their song the rolling of heavy wheels and the
2140𓀴cracking of whips; the Szgany and the Slovaks of whom the Count had
2141𓀴spoken were coming. With a last look around and at the box which
2142𓀴contained the vile body, I ran from the place and gained the Count’s
2143𓀴room, determined to rush out at the moment the door should be opened.
2144𓀴With strained ears, I listened, and heard downstairs the grinding of the
2145𓀴key in the great lock and the falling back of the heavy door. There must
2146𓀴have been some other means of entry, or some one had a key for one of
2147𓀴the locked doors. Then there came the sound of many feet tramping and
2148𓀴dying away in some passage which sent up a clanging echo. I turned to
2149𓀴run down again towards the vault, where I might find the new entrance;
2150𓀴but at the moment there seemed to come a violent puff of wind, and the
2151𓀴door to the winding stair blew to with a shock that set the dust from
2152𓀴the lintels flying. When I ran to push it open, I found that it was
2153𓀴hopelessly fast. I was again a prisoner, and the net of doom was closing
2154𓀴round me more closely.
2155𓀴
2156𓀴As I write there is in the passage below a sound of many tramping feet
2157𓀴and the crash of weights being set down heavily, doubtless the boxes,
2158𓀴with their freight of earth. There is a sound of hammering; it is the
2159𓀴box being nailed down. Now I can hear the heavy feet tramping again
2160𓀴along the hall, with many other idle feet coming behind them.
2161𓀴
2162𓀴The door is shut, and the chains rattle; there is a grinding of the key
2163𓀴in the lock; I can hear the key withdraw: then another door opens and
2164𓀴shuts; I hear the creaking of lock and bolt.
2165𓀴
2166𓀴Hark! in the courtyard and down the rocky way the roll of heavy wheels,
2167𓀴the crack of whips, and the chorus of the Szgany as they pass into the
2168𓀴distance.
2169𓀴
2170𓀴I am alone in the castle with those awful women. Faugh! Mina is a woman,
2171𓀴and there is nought in common. They are devils of the Pit!
2172𓀴
2173𓀴I shall not remain alone with them; I shall try to scale the castle wall
2174𓀴farther than I have yet attempted. I shall take some of the gold with
2175𓀴me, lest I want it later. I may find a way from this dreadful place.
2176𓀴
2177𓀴And then away for home! away to the quickest and nearest train! away
2178𓀴from this cursed spot, from this cursed land, where the devil and his
2179𓀴children still walk with earthly feet!
2180𓀴
2181𓀴At least God’s mercy is better than that of these monsters, and the
2182𓀴precipice is steep and high. At its foot a man may sleep--as a man.
2183𓀴Good-bye, all! Mina!
2184𓀴
2185𓀴
2186𓀴
2187𓀴
2188𓀴CHAPTER V
2189𓀴
2190𓀴_Letter from Miss Mina Murray to Miss Lucy Westenra._
2191𓀴
2192𓀴
2193𓀴“_9 May._
2194𓀴
2195𓀴“My dearest Lucy,--
2196𓀴
2197𓀴“Forgive my long delay in writing, but I have been simply overwhelmed
2198𓀴with work. The life of an assistant schoolmistress is sometimes trying.
2199𓀴I am longing to be with you, and by the sea, where we can talk together
2200𓀴freely and build our castles in the air. I have been working very hard
2201𓀴lately, because I want to keep up with Jonathan’s studies, and I have
2202𓀴been practising shorthand very assiduously. When we are married I shall
2203𓀴be able to be useful to Jonathan, and if I can stenograph well enough I
2204𓀴can take down what he wants to say in this way and write it out for
2205𓀴him on the typewriter, at which also I am practising very hard. He
2206𓀴and I sometimes write letters in shorthand, and he is keeping a
2207𓀴stenographic journal of his travels abroad. When I am with you I
2208𓀴shall keep a diary in the same way. I don’t mean one of those
2209𓀴two-pages-to-the-week-with-Sunday-squeezed-in-a-corner diaries, but a
2210𓀴sort of journal which I can write in whenever I feel inclined. I do not
2211𓀴suppose there will be much of interest to other people; but it is not
2212𓀴intended for them. I may show it to Jonathan some day if there is in it
2213𓀴anything worth sharing, but it is really an exercise book. I shall try
2214𓀴to do what I see lady journalists do: interviewing and writing
2215𓀴descriptions and trying to remember conversations. I am told that, with
2216𓀴a little practice, one can remember all that goes on or that one hears
2217𓀴said during a day. However, we shall see. I will tell you of my little
2218𓀴plans when we meet. I have just had a few hurried lines from Jonathan
2219𓀴from Transylvania. He is well, and will be returning in about a week. I
2220𓀴am longing to hear all his news. It must be so nice to see strange
2221𓀴countries. I wonder if we--I mean Jonathan and I--shall ever see them
2222𓀴together. There is the ten o’clock bell ringing. Good-bye.
2223𓀴
2224𓀴“Your loving
2225𓀴
2226𓀴“MINA.
2227𓀴
2228𓀴“Tell me all the news when you write. You have not told me anything for
2229𓀴a long time. I hear rumours, and especially of a tall, handsome,
2230𓀴curly-haired man???”
2231𓀴
2232𓀴
2233𓀴_Letter, Lucy Westenra to Mina Murray_.
2234𓀴
2235𓀴“_17, Chatham Street_,
2236𓀴
2237𓀴“_Wednesday_.
2238𓀴
2239𓀴“My dearest Mina,--
2240𓀴
2241𓀴“I must say you tax me _very_ unfairly with being a bad correspondent. I
2242𓀴wrote to you _twice_ since we parted, and your last letter was only your
2243𓀴_second_. Besides, I have nothing to tell you. There is really nothing
2244𓀴to interest you. Town is very pleasant just now, and we go a good deal
2245𓀴to picture-galleries and for walks and rides in the park. As to the
2246𓀴tall, curly-haired man, I suppose it was the one who was with me at the
2247𓀴last Pop. Some one has evidently been telling tales. That was Mr.
2248𓀴Holmwood. He often comes to see us, and he and mamma get on very well
2249𓀴together; they have so many things to talk about in common. We met some
2250𓀴time ago a man that would just _do for you_, if you were not already
2251𓀴engaged to Jonathan. He is an excellent _parti_, being handsome, well
2252𓀴off, and of good birth. He is a doctor and really clever. Just fancy! He
2253𓀴is only nine-and-twenty, and he has an immense lunatic asylum all under
2254𓀴his own care. Mr. Holmwood introduced him to me, and he called here to
2255𓀴see us, and often comes now. I think he is one of the most resolute men
2256𓀴I ever saw, and yet the most calm. He seems absolutely imperturbable. I
2257𓀴can fancy what a wonderful power he must have over his patients. He has
2258𓀴a curious habit of looking one straight in the face, as if trying to
2259𓀴read one’s thoughts. He tries this on very much with me, but I flatter
2260𓀴myself he has got a tough nut to crack. I know that from my glass. Do
2261𓀴you ever try to read your own face? _I do_, and I can tell you it is not
2262𓀴a bad study, and gives you more trouble than you can well fancy if you
2263𓀴have never tried it. He says that I afford him a curious psychological
2264𓀴study, and I humbly think I do. I do not, as you know, take sufficient
2265𓀴interest in dress to be able to describe the new fashions. Dress is a
2266𓀴bore. That is slang again, but never mind; Arthur says that every day.
2267𓀴There, it is all out. Mina, we have told all our secrets to each other
2268𓀴since we were _children_; we have slept together and eaten together, and
2269𓀴laughed and cried together; and now, though I have spoken, I would like
2270𓀴to speak more. Oh, Mina, couldn’t you guess? I love him. I am blushing
2271𓀴as I write, for although I _think_ he loves me, he has not told me so in
2272𓀴words. But oh, Mina, I love him; I love him; I love him! There, that
2273𓀴does me good. I wish I were with you, dear, sitting by the fire
2274𓀴undressing, as we used to sit; and I would try to tell you what I feel.
2275𓀴I do not know how I am writing this even to you. I am afraid to stop,
2276𓀴or I should tear up the letter, and I don’t want to stop, for I _do_ so
2277𓀴want to tell you all. Let me hear from you _at once_, and tell me all
2278𓀴that you think about it. Mina, I must stop. Good-night. Bless me in your
2279𓀴prayers; and, Mina, pray for my happiness.
2280𓀴
2281𓀴“LUCY.
2282𓀴
2283𓀴“P.S.--I need not tell you this is a secret. Good-night again.
2284𓀴
2285𓀴“L.”
2286𓀴
2287𓀴_Letter, Lucy Westenra to Mina Murray_.
2288𓀴
2289𓀴“_24 May_.
2290𓀴
2291𓀴“My dearest Mina,--
2292𓀴
2293𓀴“Thanks, and thanks, and thanks again for your sweet letter. It was so
2294𓀴nice to be able to tell you and to have your sympathy.
2295𓀴
2296𓀴“My dear, it never rains but it pours. How true the old proverbs are.
2297𓀴Here am I, who shall be twenty in September, and yet I never had a
2298𓀴proposal till to-day, not a real proposal, and to-day I have had three.
2299𓀴Just fancy! THREE proposals in one day! Isn’t it awful! I feel sorry,
2300𓀴really and truly sorry, for two of the poor fellows. Oh, Mina, I am so
2301𓀴happy that I don’t know what to do with myself. And three proposals!
2302𓀴But, for goodness’ sake, don’t tell any of the girls, or they would be
2303𓀴getting all sorts of extravagant ideas and imagining themselves injured
2304𓀴and slighted if in their very first day at home they did not get six at
2305𓀴least. Some girls are so vain! You and I, Mina dear, who are engaged and
2306𓀴are going to settle down soon soberly into old married women, can
2307𓀴despise vanity. Well, I must tell you about the three, but you must keep
2308𓀴it a secret, dear, from _every one_, except, of course, Jonathan. You
2309𓀴will tell him, because I would, if I were in your place, certainly tell
2310𓀴Arthur. A woman ought to tell her husband everything--don’t you think
2311𓀴so, dear?--and I must be fair. Men like women, certainly their wives, to
2312𓀴be quite as fair as they are; and women, I am afraid, are not always
2313𓀴quite as fair as they should be. Well, my dear, number One came just
2314𓀴before lunch. I told you of him, Dr. John Seward, the lunatic-asylum
2315𓀴man, with the strong jaw and the good forehead. He was very cool
2316𓀴outwardly, but was nervous all the same. He had evidently been schooling
2317𓀴himself as to all sorts of little things, and remembered them; but he
2318𓀴almost managed to sit down on his silk hat, which men don’t generally do
2319𓀴when they are cool, and then when he wanted to appear at ease he kept
2320𓀴playing with a lancet in a way that made me nearly scream. He spoke to
2321𓀴me, Mina, very straightforwardly. He told me how dear I was to him,
2322𓀴though he had known me so little, and what his life would be with me to
2323𓀴help and cheer him. He was going to tell me how unhappy he would be if I
2324𓀴did not care for him, but when he saw me cry he said that he was a brute
2325𓀴and would not add to my present trouble. Then he broke off and asked if
2326𓀴I could love him in time; and when I shook my head his hands trembled,
2327𓀴and then with some hesitation he asked me if I cared already for any one
2328𓀴else. He put it very nicely, saying that he did not want to wring my
2329𓀴confidence from me, but only to know, because if a woman’s heart was
2330𓀴free a man might have hope. And then, Mina, I felt a sort of duty to
2331𓀴tell him that there was some one. I only told him that much, and then he
2332𓀴stood up, and he looked very strong and very grave as he took both my
2333𓀴hands in his and said he hoped I would be happy, and that if I ever
2334𓀴wanted a friend I must count him one of my best. Oh, Mina dear, I can’t
2335𓀴help crying: and you must excuse this letter being all blotted. Being
2336𓀴proposed to is all very nice and all that sort of thing, but it isn’t at
2337𓀴all a happy thing when you have to see a poor fellow, whom you know
2338𓀴loves you honestly, going away and looking all broken-hearted, and to
2339𓀴know that, no matter what he may say at the moment, you are passing
2340𓀴quite out of his life. My dear, I must stop here at present, I feel so
2341𓀴miserable, though I am so happy.
2342𓀴
2343𓀴“_Evening._
2344𓀴
2345𓀴“Arthur has just gone, and I feel in better spirits than when I left
2346𓀴off, so I can go on telling you about the day. Well, my dear, number Two
2347𓀴came after lunch. He is such a nice fellow, an American from Texas, and
2348𓀴he looks so young and so fresh that it seems almost impossible that he
2349𓀴has been to so many places and has had such adventures. I sympathise
2350𓀴with poor Desdemona when she had such a dangerous stream poured in her
2351𓀴ear, even by a black man. I suppose that we women are such cowards that
2352𓀴we think a man will save us from fears, and we marry him. I know now
2353𓀴what I would do if I were a man and wanted to make a girl love me. No, I
2354𓀴don’t, for there was Mr. Morris telling us his stories, and Arthur never
2355𓀴told any, and yet---- My dear, I am somewhat previous. Mr. Quincey P.
2356𓀴Morris found me alone. It seems that a man always does find a girl
2357𓀴alone. No, he doesn’t, for Arthur tried twice to _make_ a chance, and I
2358𓀴helping him all I could; I am not ashamed to say it now. I must tell you
2359𓀴beforehand that Mr. Morris doesn’t always speak slang--that is to say,
2360𓀴he never does so to strangers or before them, for he is really well
2361𓀴educated and has exquisite manners--but he found out that it amused me
2362𓀴to hear him talk American slang, and whenever I was present, and there
2363𓀴was no one to be shocked, he said such funny things. I am afraid, my
2364𓀴dear, he has to invent it all, for it fits exactly into whatever else he
2365𓀴has to say. But this is a way slang has. I do not know myself if I shall
2366𓀴ever speak slang; I do not know if Arthur likes it, as I have never
2367𓀴heard him use any as yet. Well, Mr. Morris sat down beside me and looked
2368𓀴as happy and jolly as he could, but I could see all the same that he was
2369𓀴very nervous. He took my hand in his, and said ever so sweetly:--
2370𓀴
2371𓀴“‘Miss Lucy, I know I ain’t good enough to regulate the fixin’s of your
2372𓀴little shoes, but I guess if you wait till you find a man that is you
2373𓀴will go join them seven young women with the lamps when you quit. Won’t
2374𓀴you just hitch up alongside of me and let us go down the long road
2375𓀴together, driving in double harness?’
2376𓀴
2377𓀴“Well, he did look so good-humoured and so jolly that it didn’t seem
2378𓀴half so hard to refuse him as it did poor Dr. Seward; so I said, as
2379𓀴lightly as I could, that I did not know anything of hitching, and that I
2380𓀴wasn’t broken to harness at all yet. Then he said that he had spoken in
2381𓀴a light manner, and he hoped that if he had made a mistake in doing so
2382𓀴on so grave, so momentous, an occasion for him, I would forgive him. He
2383𓀴really did look serious when he was saying it, and I couldn’t help
2384𓀴feeling a bit serious too--I know, Mina, you will think me a horrid
2385𓀴flirt--though I couldn’t help feeling a sort of exultation that he was
2386𓀴number two in one day. And then, my dear, before I could say a word he
2387𓀴began pouring out a perfect torrent of love-making, laying his very
2388𓀴heart and soul at my feet. He looked so earnest over it that I shall
2389𓀴never again think that a man must be playful always, and never earnest,
2390𓀴because he is merry at times. I suppose he saw something in my face
2391𓀴which checked him, for he suddenly stopped, and said with a sort of
2392𓀴manly fervour that I could have loved him for if I had been free:--
2393𓀴
2394𓀴“‘Lucy, you are an honest-hearted girl, I know. I should not be here
2395𓀴speaking to you as I am now if I did not believe you clean grit, right
2396𓀴through to the very depths of your soul. Tell me, like one good fellow
2397𓀴to another, is there any one else that you care for? And if there is
2398𓀴I’ll never trouble you a hair’s breadth again, but will be, if you will
2399𓀴let me, a very faithful friend.’
2400𓀴
2401𓀴“My dear Mina, why are men so noble when we women are so little worthy
2402𓀴of them? Here was I almost making fun of this great-hearted, true
2403𓀴gentleman. I burst into tears--I am afraid, my dear, you will think
2404𓀴this a very sloppy letter in more ways than one--and I really felt very
2405𓀴badly. Why can’t they let a girl marry three men, or as many as want
2406𓀴her, and save all this trouble? But this is heresy, and I must not say
2407𓀴it. I am glad to say that, though I was crying, I was able to look into
2408𓀴Mr. Morris’s brave eyes, and I told him out straight:--
2409𓀴
2410𓀴“‘Yes, there is some one I love, though he has not told me yet that he
2411𓀴even loves me.’ I was right to speak to him so frankly, for quite a
2412𓀴light came into his face, and he put out both his hands and took mine--I
2413𓀴think I put them into his--and said in a hearty way:--
2414𓀴
2415𓀴“‘That’s my brave girl. It’s better worth being late for a chance of
2416𓀴winning you than being in time for any other girl in the world. Don’t
2417𓀴cry, my dear. If it’s for me, I’m a hard nut to crack; and I take it
2418𓀴standing up. If that other fellow doesn’t know his happiness, well, he’d
2419𓀴better look for it soon, or he’ll have to deal with me. Little girl,
2420𓀴your honesty and pluck have made me a friend, and that’s rarer than a
2421𓀴lover; it’s more unselfish anyhow. My dear, I’m going to have a pretty
2422𓀴lonely walk between this and Kingdom Come. Won’t you give me one kiss?
2423𓀴It’ll be something to keep off the darkness now and then. You can, you
2424𓀴know, if you like, for that other good fellow--he must be a good fellow,
2425𓀴my dear, and a fine fellow, or you could not love him--hasn’t spoken
2426𓀴yet.’ That quite won me, Mina, for it _was_ brave and sweet of him, and
2427𓀴noble, too, to a rival--wasn’t it?--and he so sad; so I leant over and
2428𓀴kissed him. He stood up with my two hands in his, and as he looked down
2429𓀴into my face--I am afraid I was blushing very much--he said:--
2430𓀴
2431𓀴“‘Little girl, I hold your hand, and you’ve kissed me, and if these
2432𓀴things don’t make us friends nothing ever will. Thank you for your sweet
2433𓀴honesty to me, and good-bye.’ He wrung my hand, and taking up his hat,
2434𓀴went straight out of the room without looking back, without a tear or a
2435𓀴quiver or a pause; and I am crying like a baby. Oh, why must a man like
2436𓀴that be made unhappy when there are lots of girls about who would
2437𓀴worship the very ground he trod on? I know I would if I were free--only
2438𓀴I don’t want to be free. My dear, this quite upset me, and I feel I
2439𓀴cannot write of happiness just at once, after telling you of it; and I
2440𓀴don’t wish to tell of the number three until it can be all happy.
2441𓀴
2442𓀴“Ever your loving
2443𓀴
2444𓀴“LUCY.
2445𓀴
2446𓀴“P.S.--Oh, about number Three--I needn’t tell you of number Three, need
2447𓀴I? Besides, it was all so confused; it seemed only a moment from his
2448𓀴coming into the room till both his arms were round me, and he was
2449𓀴kissing me. I am very, very happy, and I don’t know what I have done to
2450𓀴deserve it. I must only try in the future to show that I am not
2451𓀴ungrateful to God for all His goodness to me in sending to me such a
2452𓀴lover, such a husband, and such a friend.
2453𓀴
2454𓀴“Good-bye.”
2455𓀴
2456𓀴
2457𓀴_Dr. Seward’s Diary._
2458𓀴
2459𓀴(Kept in phonograph)
2460𓀴
2461𓀴_25 May._--Ebb tide in appetite to-day. Cannot eat, cannot rest, so
2462𓀴diary instead. Since my rebuff of yesterday I have a sort of empty
2463𓀴feeling; nothing in the world seems of sufficient importance to be worth
2464𓀴the doing.... As I knew that the only cure for this sort of thing was
2465𓀴work, I went down amongst the patients. I picked out one who has
2466𓀴afforded me a study of much interest. He is so quaint that I am
2467𓀴determined to understand him as well as I can. To-day I seemed to get
2468𓀴nearer than ever before to the heart of his mystery.
2469𓀴
2470𓀴I questioned him more fully than I had ever done, with a view to making
2471𓀴myself master of the facts of his hallucination. In my manner of doing
2472𓀴it there was, I now see, something of cruelty. I seemed to wish to keep
2473𓀴him to the point of his madness--a thing which I avoid with the patients
2474𓀴as I would the mouth of hell.
2475𓀴
2476𓀴(_Mem._, under what circumstances would I _not_ avoid the pit of hell?)
2477𓀴_Omnia Romæ venalia sunt._ Hell has its price! _verb. sap._ If there be
2478𓀴anything behind this instinct it will be valuable to trace it afterwards
2479𓀴_accurately_, so I had better commence to do so, therefore--
2480𓀴
2481𓀴R. M. Renfield, ætat 59.--Sanguine temperament; great physical strength;
2482𓀴morbidly excitable; periods of gloom, ending in some fixed idea which I
2483𓀴cannot make out. I presume that the sanguine temperament itself and the
2484𓀴disturbing influence end in a mentally-accomplished finish; a possibly
2485𓀴dangerous man, probably dangerous if unselfish. In selfish men caution
2486𓀴is as secure an armour for their foes as for themselves. What I think of
2487𓀴on this point is, when self is the fixed point the centripetal force is
2488𓀴balanced with the centrifugal; when duty, a cause, etc., is the fixed
2489𓀴point, the latter force is paramount, and only accident or a series of
2490𓀴accidents can balance it.
2491𓀴
2492𓀴
2493𓀴_Letter, Quincey P. Morris to Hon. Arthur Holmwood._
2494𓀴
2495𓀴“_25 May._
2496𓀴
2497𓀴“My dear Art,--
2498𓀴
2499𓀴“We’ve told yarns by the camp-fire in the prairies; and dressed one
2500𓀴another’s wounds after trying a landing at the Marquesas; and drunk
2501𓀴healths on the shore of Titicaca. There are more yarns to be told, and
2502𓀴other wounds to be healed, and another health to be drunk. Won’t you let
2503𓀴this be at my camp-fire to-morrow night? I have no hesitation in asking
2504𓀴you, as I know a certain lady is engaged to a certain dinner-party, and
2505𓀴that you are free. There will only be one other, our old pal at the
2506𓀴Korea, Jack Seward. He’s coming, too, and we both want to mingle our
2507𓀴weeps over the wine-cup, and to drink a health with all our hearts to
2508𓀴the happiest man in all the wide world, who has won the noblest heart
2509𓀴that God has made and the best worth winning. We promise you a hearty
2510𓀴welcome, and a loving greeting, and a health as true as your own right
2511𓀴hand. We shall both swear to leave you at home if you drink too deep to
2512𓀴a certain pair of eyes. Come!
2513𓀴
2514𓀴“Yours, as ever and always,
2515𓀴
2516𓀴“QUINCEY P. MORRIS.”
2517𓀴
2518𓀴
2519𓀴_Telegram from Arthur Holmwood to Quincey P. Morris._
2520𓀴
2521𓀴“_26 May._
2522𓀴
2523𓀴“Count me in every time. I bear messages which will make both your ears
2524𓀴tingle.
2525𓀴
2526𓀴“ART.”
2527𓀴
2528𓀴
2529𓀴
2530𓀴
2531𓀴CHAPTER VI
2532𓀴
2533𓀴MINA MURRAY’S JOURNAL
2534𓀴
2535𓀴
2536𓀴_24 July. Whitby._--Lucy met me at the station, looking sweeter and
2537𓀴lovelier than ever, and we drove up to the house at the Crescent in
2538𓀴which they have rooms. This is a lovely place. The little river, the
2539𓀴Esk, runs through a deep valley, which broadens out as it comes near the
2540𓀴harbour. A great viaduct runs across, with high piers, through which the
2541𓀴view seems somehow further away than it really is. The valley is
2542𓀴beautifully green, and it is so steep that when you are on the high land
2543𓀴on either side you look right across it, unless you are near enough to
2544𓀴see down. The houses of the old town--the side away from us--are all
2545𓀴red-roofed, and seem piled up one over the other anyhow, like the
2546𓀴pictures we see of Nuremberg. Right over the town is the ruin of Whitby
2547𓀴Abbey, which was sacked by the Danes, and which is the scene of part of
2548𓀴“Marmion,” where the girl was built up in the wall. It is a most noble
2549𓀴ruin, of immense size, and full of beautiful and romantic bits; there is
2550𓀴a legend that a white lady is seen in one of the windows. Between it and
2551𓀴the town there is another church, the parish one, round which is a big
2552𓀴graveyard, all full of tombstones. This is to my mind the nicest spot in
2553𓀴Whitby, for it lies right over the town, and has a full view of the
2554𓀴harbour and all up the bay to where the headland called Kettleness
2555𓀴stretches out into the sea. It descends so steeply over the harbour that
2556𓀴part of the bank has fallen away, and some of the graves have been
2557𓀴destroyed. In one place part of the stonework of the graves stretches
2558𓀴out over the sandy pathway far below. There are walks, with seats beside
2559𓀴them, through the churchyard; and people go and sit there all day long
2560𓀴looking at the beautiful view and enjoying the breeze. I shall come and
2561𓀴sit here very often myself and work. Indeed, I am writing now, with my
2562𓀴book on my knee, and listening to the talk of three old men who are
2563𓀴sitting beside me. They seem to do nothing all day but sit up here and
2564𓀴talk.
2565𓀴
2566𓀴The harbour lies below me, with, on the far side, one long granite wall
2567𓀴stretching out into the sea, with a curve outwards at the end of it, in
2568𓀴the middle of which is a lighthouse. A heavy sea-wall runs along outside
2569𓀴of it. On the near side, the sea-wall makes an elbow crooked inversely,
2570𓀴and its end too has a lighthouse. Between the two piers there is a
2571𓀴narrow opening into the harbour, which then suddenly widens.
2572𓀴
2573𓀴It is nice at high water; but when the tide is out it shoals away to
2574𓀴nothing, and there is merely the stream of the Esk, running between
2575𓀴banks of sand, with rocks here and there. Outside the harbour on this
2576𓀴side there rises for about half a mile a great reef, the sharp edge of
2577𓀴which runs straight out from behind the south lighthouse. At the end of
2578𓀴it is a buoy with a bell, which swings in bad weather, and sends in a
2579𓀴mournful sound on the wind. They have a legend here that when a ship is
2580𓀴lost bells are heard out at sea. I must ask the old man about this; he
2581𓀴is coming this way....
2582𓀴
2583𓀴He is a funny old man. He must be awfully old, for his face is all
2584𓀴gnarled and twisted like the bark of a tree. He tells me that he is
2585𓀴nearly a hundred, and that he was a sailor in the Greenland fishing
2586𓀴fleet when Waterloo was fought. He is, I am afraid, a very sceptical
2587𓀴person, for when I asked him about the bells at sea and the White Lady
2588𓀴at the abbey he said very brusquely:--
2589𓀴
2590𓀴“I wouldn’t fash masel’ about them, miss. Them things be all wore out.
2591𓀴Mind, I don’t say that they never was, but I do say that they wasn’t in
2592𓀴my time. They be all very well for comers and trippers, an’ the like,
2593𓀴but not for a nice young lady like you. Them feet-folks from York and
2594𓀴Leeds that be always eatin’ cured herrin’s an’ drinkin’ tea an’ lookin’
2595𓀴out to buy cheap jet would creed aught. I wonder masel’ who’d be
2596𓀴bothered tellin’ lies to them--even the newspapers, which is full of
2597𓀴fool-talk.” I thought he would be a good person to learn interesting
2598𓀴things from, so I asked him if he would mind telling me something about
2599𓀴the whale-fishing in the old days. He was just settling himself to begin
2600𓀴when the clock struck six, whereupon he laboured to get up, and said:--
2601𓀴
2602𓀴“I must gang ageeanwards home now, miss. My grand-daughter doesn’t like
2603𓀴to be kept waitin’ when the tea is ready, for it takes me time to
2604𓀴crammle aboon the grees, for there be a many of ’em; an’, miss, I lack
2605𓀴belly-timber sairly by the clock.”
2606𓀴
2607𓀴He hobbled away, and I could see him hurrying, as well as he could, down
2608𓀴the steps. The steps are a great feature on the place. They lead from
2609𓀴the town up to the church, there are hundreds of them--I do not know how
2610𓀴many--and they wind up in a delicate curve; the slope is so gentle that
2611𓀴a horse could easily walk up and down them. I think they must originally
2612𓀴have had something to do with the abbey. I shall go home too. Lucy went
2613𓀴out visiting with her mother, and as they were only duty calls, I did
2614𓀴not go. They will be home by this.
2615𓀴
2616𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
2617𓀴
2618𓀴_1 August._--I came up here an hour ago with Lucy, and we had a most
2619𓀴interesting talk with my old friend and the two others who always come
2620𓀴and join him. He is evidently the Sir Oracle of them, and I should think
2621𓀴must have been in his time a most dictatorial person. He will not admit
2622𓀴anything, and downfaces everybody. If he can’t out-argue them he bullies
2623𓀴them, and then takes their silence for agreement with his views. Lucy
2624𓀴was looking sweetly pretty in her white lawn frock; she has got a
2625𓀴beautiful colour since she has been here. I noticed that the old men did
2626𓀴not lose any time in coming up and sitting near her when we sat down.
2627𓀴She is so sweet with old people; I think they all fell in love with her
2628𓀴on the spot. Even my old man succumbed and did not contradict her, but
2629𓀴gave me double share instead. I got him on the subject of the legends,
2630𓀴and he went off at once into a sort of sermon. I must try to remember it
2631𓀴and put it down:--
2632𓀴
2633𓀴“It be all fool-talk, lock, stock, and barrel; that’s what it be, an’
2634𓀴nowt else. These bans an’ wafts an’ boh-ghosts an’ barguests an’ bogles
2635𓀴an’ all anent them is only fit to set bairns an’ dizzy women
2636𓀴a-belderin’. They be nowt but air-blebs. They, an’ all grims an’ signs
2637𓀴an’ warnin’s, be all invented by parsons an’ illsome beuk-bodies an’
2638𓀴railway touters to skeer an’ scunner hafflin’s, an’ to get folks to do
2639𓀴somethin’ that they don’t other incline to. It makes me ireful to think
2640𓀴o’ them. Why, it’s them that, not content with printin’ lies on paper
2641𓀴an’ preachin’ them out of pulpits, does want to be cuttin’ them on the
2642𓀴tombstones. Look here all around you in what airt ye will; all them
2643𓀴steans, holdin’ up their heads as well as they can out of their pride,
2644𓀴is acant--simply tumblin’ down with the weight o’ the lies wrote on
2645𓀴them, ‘Here lies the body’ or ‘Sacred to the memory’ wrote on all of
2646𓀴them, an’ yet in nigh half of them there bean’t no bodies at all; an’
2647𓀴the memories of them bean’t cared a pinch of snuff about, much less
2648𓀴sacred. Lies all of them, nothin’ but lies of one kind or another! My
2649𓀴gog, but it’ll be a quare scowderment at the Day of Judgment when they
2650𓀴come tumblin’ up in their death-sarks, all jouped together an’ tryin’ to
2651𓀴drag their tombsteans with them to prove how good they was; some of them
2652𓀴trimmlin’ and ditherin’, with their hands that dozzened an’ slippy from
2653𓀴lyin’ in the sea that they can’t even keep their grup o’ them.”
2654𓀴
2655𓀴I could see from the old fellow’s self-satisfied air and the way in
2656𓀴which he looked round for the approval of his cronies that he was
2657𓀴“showing off,” so I put in a word to keep him going:--
2658𓀴
2659𓀴“Oh, Mr. Swales, you can’t be serious. Surely these tombstones are not
2660𓀴all wrong?”
2661𓀴
2662𓀴“Yabblins! There may be a poorish few not wrong, savin’ where they make
2663𓀴out the people too good; for there be folk that do think a balm-bowl be
2664𓀴like the sea, if only it be their own. The whole thing be only lies. Now
2665𓀴look you here; you come here a stranger, an’ you see this kirk-garth.” I
2666𓀴nodded, for I thought it better to assent, though I did not quite
2667𓀴understand his dialect. I knew it had something to do with the church.
2668𓀴He went on: “And you consate that all these steans be aboon folk that be
2669𓀴happed here, snod an’ snog?” I assented again. “Then that be just where
2670𓀴the lie comes in. Why, there be scores of these lay-beds that be toom as
2671𓀴old Dun’s ’bacca-box on Friday night.” He nudged one of his companions,
2672𓀴and they all laughed. “And my gog! how could they be otherwise? Look at
2673𓀴that one, the aftest abaft the bier-bank: read it!” I went over and
2674𓀴read:--
2675𓀴
2676𓀴“Edward Spencelagh, master mariner, murdered by pirates off the coast of
2677𓀴Andres, April, 1854, æt. 30.” When I came back Mr. Swales went on:--
2678𓀴
2679𓀴“Who brought him home, I wonder, to hap him here? Murdered off the coast
2680𓀴of Andres! an’ you consated his body lay under! Why, I could name ye a
2681𓀴dozen whose bones lie in the Greenland seas above”--he pointed
