2682𓀴northwards--“or where the currents may have drifted them. There be the
2683𓀴steans around ye. Ye can, with your young eyes, read the small-print of
2684𓀴the lies from here. This Braithwaite Lowrey--I knew his father, lost in
2685𓀴the _Lively_ off Greenland in ’20; or Andrew Woodhouse, drowned in the
2686𓀴same seas in 1777; or John Paxton, drowned off Cape Farewell a year
2687𓀴later; or old John Rawlings, whose grandfather sailed with me, drowned
2688𓀴in the Gulf of Finland in ’50. Do ye think that all these men will have
2689𓀴to make a rush to Whitby when the trumpet sounds? I have me antherums
2690𓀴aboot it! I tell ye that when they got here they’d be jommlin’ an’
2691𓀴jostlin’ one another that way that it ’ud be like a fight up on the ice
2692𓀴in the old days, when we’d be at one another from daylight to dark, an’
2693𓀴tryin’ to tie up our cuts by the light of the aurora borealis.” This was
2694𓀴evidently local pleasantry, for the old man cackled over it, and his
2695𓀴cronies joined in with gusto.
2696𓀴
2697𓀴“But,” I said, “surely you are not quite correct, for you start on the
2698𓀴assumption that all the poor people, or their spirits, will have to
2699𓀴take their tombstones with them on the Day of Judgment. Do you think
2700𓀴that will be really necessary?”
2701𓀴
2702𓀴“Well, what else be they tombstones for? Answer me that, miss!”
2703𓀴
2704𓀴“To please their relatives, I suppose.”
2705𓀴
2706𓀴“To please their relatives, you suppose!” This he said with intense
2707𓀴scorn. “How will it pleasure their relatives to know that lies is wrote
2708𓀴over them, and that everybody in the place knows that they be lies?” He
2709𓀴pointed to a stone at our feet which had been laid down as a slab, on
2710𓀴which the seat was rested, close to the edge of the cliff. “Read the
2711𓀴lies on that thruff-stean,” he said. The letters were upside down to me
2712𓀴from where I sat, but Lucy was more opposite to them, so she leant over
2713𓀴and read:--
2714𓀴
2715𓀴“Sacred to the memory of George Canon, who died, in the hope of a
2716𓀴glorious resurrection, on July, 29, 1873, falling from the rocks at
2717𓀴Kettleness. This tomb was erected by his sorrowing mother to her dearly
2718𓀴beloved son. ‘He was the only son of his mother, and she was a widow.’
2719𓀴Really, Mr. Swales, I don’t see anything very funny in that!” She spoke
2720𓀴her comment very gravely and somewhat severely.
2721𓀴
2722𓀴“Ye don’t see aught funny! Ha! ha! But that’s because ye don’t gawm the
2723𓀴sorrowin’ mother was a hell-cat that hated him because he was
2724𓀴acrewk’d--a regular lamiter he was--an’ he hated her so that he
2725𓀴committed suicide in order that she mightn’t get an insurance she put on
2726𓀴his life. He blew nigh the top of his head off with an old musket that
2727𓀴they had for scarin’ the crows with. ’Twarn’t for crows then, for it
2728𓀴brought the clegs and the dowps to him. That’s the way he fell off the
2729𓀴rocks. And, as to hopes of a glorious resurrection, I’ve often heard him
2730𓀴say masel’ that he hoped he’d go to hell, for his mother was so pious
2731𓀴that she’d be sure to go to heaven, an’ he didn’t want to addle where
2732𓀴she was. Now isn’t that stean at any rate”--he hammered it with his
2733𓀴stick as he spoke--“a pack of lies? and won’t it make Gabriel keckle
2734𓀴when Geordie comes pantin’ up the grees with the tombstean balanced on
2735𓀴his hump, and asks it to be took as evidence!”
2736𓀴
2737𓀴I did not know what to say, but Lucy turned the conversation as she
2738𓀴said, rising up:--
2739𓀴
2740𓀴“Oh, why did you tell us of this? It is my favourite seat, and I cannot
2741𓀴leave it; and now I find I must go on sitting over the grave of a
2742𓀴suicide.”
2743𓀴
2744𓀴“That won’t harm ye, my pretty; an’ it may make poor Geordie gladsome to
2745𓀴have so trim a lass sittin’ on his lap. That won’t hurt ye. Why, I’ve
2746𓀴sat here off an’ on for nigh twenty years past, an’ it hasn’t done me
2747𓀴no harm. Don’t ye fash about them as lies under ye, or that doesn’ lie
2748𓀴there either! It’ll be time for ye to be getting scart when ye see the
2749𓀴tombsteans all run away with, and the place as bare as a stubble-field.
2750𓀴There’s the clock, an’ I must gang. My service to ye, ladies!” And off
2751𓀴he hobbled.
2752𓀴
2753𓀴Lucy and I sat awhile, and it was all so beautiful before us that we
2754𓀴took hands as we sat; and she told me all over again about Arthur and
2755𓀴their coming marriage. That made me just a little heart-sick, for I
2756𓀴haven’t heard from Jonathan for a whole month.
2757𓀴
2758𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
2759𓀴
2760𓀴_The same day._ I came up here alone, for I am very sad. There was no
2761𓀴letter for me. I hope there cannot be anything the matter with Jonathan.
2762𓀴The clock has just struck nine. I see the lights scattered all over the
2763𓀴town, sometimes in rows where the streets are, and sometimes singly;
2764𓀴they run right up the Esk and die away in the curve of the valley. To my
2765𓀴left the view is cut off by a black line of roof of the old house next
2766𓀴the abbey. The sheep and lambs are bleating in the fields away behind
2767𓀴me, and there is a clatter of a donkey’s hoofs up the paved road below.
2768𓀴The band on the pier is playing a harsh waltz in good time, and further
2769𓀴along the quay there is a Salvation Army meeting in a back street.
2770𓀴Neither of the bands hears the other, but up here I hear and see them
2771𓀴both. I wonder where Jonathan is and if he is thinking of me! I wish he
2772𓀴were here.
2773𓀴
2774𓀴
2775𓀴_Dr. Seward’s Diary._
2776𓀴
2777𓀴_5 June._--The case of Renfield grows more interesting the more I get to
2778𓀴understand the man. He has certain qualities very largely developed;
2779𓀴selfishness, secrecy, and purpose. I wish I could get at what is the
2780𓀴object of the latter. He seems to have some settled scheme of his own,
2781𓀴but what it is I do not yet know. His redeeming quality is a love of
2782𓀴animals, though, indeed, he has such curious turns in it that I
2783𓀴sometimes imagine he is only abnormally cruel. His pets are of odd
2784𓀴sorts. Just now his hobby is catching flies. He has at present such a
2785𓀴quantity that I have had myself to expostulate. To my astonishment, he
2786𓀴did not break out into a fury, as I expected, but took the matter in
2787𓀴simple seriousness. He thought for a moment, and then said: “May I have
2788𓀴three days? I shall clear them away.” Of course, I said that would do. I
2789𓀴must watch him.
2790𓀴
2791𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
2792𓀴
2793𓀴_18 June._--He has turned his mind now to spiders, and has got several
2794𓀴very big fellows in a box. He keeps feeding them with his flies, and
2795𓀴the number of the latter is becoming sensibly diminished, although he
2796𓀴has used half his food in attracting more flies from outside to his
2797𓀴room.
2798𓀴
2799𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
2800𓀴
2801𓀴_1 July._--His spiders are now becoming as great a nuisance as his
2802𓀴flies, and to-day I told him that he must get rid of them. He looked
2803𓀴very sad at this, so I said that he must clear out some of them, at all
2804𓀴events. He cheerfully acquiesced in this, and I gave him the same time
2805𓀴as before for reduction. He disgusted me much while with him, for when a
2806𓀴horrid blow-fly, bloated with some carrion food, buzzed into the room,
2807𓀴he caught it, held it exultantly for a few moments between his finger
2808𓀴and thumb, and, before I knew what he was going to do, put it in his
2809𓀴mouth and ate it. I scolded him for it, but he argued quietly that it
2810𓀴was very good and very wholesome; that it was life, strong life, and
2811𓀴gave life to him. This gave me an idea, or the rudiment of one. I must
2812𓀴watch how he gets rid of his spiders. He has evidently some deep problem
2813𓀴in his mind, for he keeps a little note-book in which he is always
2814𓀴jotting down something. Whole pages of it are filled with masses of
2815𓀴figures, generally single numbers added up in batches, and then the
2816𓀴totals added in batches again, as though he were “focussing” some
2817𓀴account, as the auditors put it.
2818𓀴
2819𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
2820𓀴
2821𓀴_8 July._--There is a method in his madness, and the rudimentary idea in
2822𓀴my mind is growing. It will be a whole idea soon, and then, oh,
2823𓀴unconscious cerebration! you will have to give the wall to your
2824𓀴conscious brother. I kept away from my friend for a few days, so that I
2825𓀴might notice if there were any change. Things remain as they were except
2826𓀴that he has parted with some of his pets and got a new one. He has
2827𓀴managed to get a sparrow, and has already partially tamed it. His means
2828𓀴of taming is simple, for already the spiders have diminished. Those that
2829𓀴do remain, however, are well fed, for he still brings in the flies by
2830𓀴tempting them with his food.
2831𓀴
2832𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
2833𓀴
2834𓀴_19 July._--We are progressing. My friend has now a whole colony of
2835𓀴sparrows, and his flies and spiders are almost obliterated. When I came
2836𓀴in he ran to me and said he wanted to ask me a great favour--a very,
2837𓀴very great favour; and as he spoke he fawned on me like a dog. I asked
2838𓀴him what it was, and he said, with a sort of rapture in his voice and
2839𓀴bearing:--
2840𓀴
2841𓀴“A kitten, a nice little, sleek playful kitten, that I can play with,
2842𓀴and teach, and feed--and feed--and feed!” I was not unprepared for this
2843𓀴request, for I had noticed how his pets went on increasing in size and
2844𓀴vivacity, but I did not care that his pretty family of tame sparrows
2845𓀴should be wiped out in the same manner as the flies and the spiders; so
2846𓀴I said I would see about it, and asked him if he would not rather have a
2847𓀴cat than a kitten. His eagerness betrayed him as he answered:--
2848𓀴
2849𓀴“Oh, yes, I would like a cat! I only asked for a kitten lest you should
2850𓀴refuse me a cat. No one would refuse me a kitten, would they?” I shook
2851𓀴my head, and said that at present I feared it would not be possible, but
2852𓀴that I would see about it. His face fell, and I could see a warning of
2853𓀴danger in it, for there was a sudden fierce, sidelong look which meant
2854𓀴killing. The man is an undeveloped homicidal maniac. I shall test him
2855𓀴with his present craving and see how it will work out; then I shall know
2856𓀴more.
2857𓀴
2858𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
2859𓀴
2860𓀴_10 p. m._--I have visited him again and found him sitting in a corner
2861𓀴brooding. When I came in he threw himself on his knees before me and
2862𓀴implored me to let him have a cat; that his salvation depended upon it.
2863𓀴I was firm, however, and told him that he could not have it, whereupon
2864𓀴he went without a word, and sat down, gnawing his fingers, in the corner
2865𓀴where I had found him. I shall see him in the morning early.
2866𓀴
2867𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
2868𓀴
2869𓀴_20 July._--Visited Renfield very early, before the attendant went his
2870𓀴rounds. Found him up and humming a tune. He was spreading out his sugar,
2871𓀴which he had saved, in the window, and was manifestly beginning his
2872𓀴fly-catching again; and beginning it cheerfully and with a good grace. I
2873𓀴looked around for his birds, and not seeing them, asked him where they
2874𓀴were. He replied, without turning round, that they had all flown away.
2875𓀴There were a few feathers about the room and on his pillow a drop of
2876𓀴blood. I said nothing, but went and told the keeper to report to me if
2877𓀴there were anything odd about him during the day.
2878𓀴
2879𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
2880𓀴
2881𓀴_11 a. m._--The attendant has just been to me to say that Renfield has
2882𓀴been very sick and has disgorged a whole lot of feathers. “My belief is,
2883𓀴doctor,” he said, “that he has eaten his birds, and that he just took
2884𓀴and ate them raw!”
2885𓀴
2886𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
2887𓀴
2888𓀴_11 p. m._--I gave Renfield a strong opiate to-night, enough to make
2889𓀴even him sleep, and took away his pocket-book to look at it. The thought
2890𓀴that has been buzzing about my brain lately is complete, and the theory
2891𓀴proved. My homicidal maniac is of a peculiar kind. I shall have to
2892𓀴invent a new classification for him, and call him a zoöphagous
2893𓀴(life-eating) maniac; what he desires is to absorb as many lives as he
2894𓀴can, and he has laid himself out to achieve it in a cumulative way. He
2895𓀴gave many flies to one spider and many spiders to one bird, and then
2896𓀴wanted a cat to eat the many birds. What would have been his later
2897𓀴steps? It would almost be worth while to complete the experiment. It
2898𓀴might be done if there were only a sufficient cause. Men sneered at
2899𓀴vivisection, and yet look at its results to-day! Why not advance science
2900𓀴in its most difficult and vital aspect--the knowledge of the brain? Had
2901𓀴I even the secret of one such mind--did I hold the key to the fancy of
2902𓀴even one lunatic--I might advance my own branch of science to a pitch
2903𓀴compared with which Burdon-Sanderson’s physiology or Ferrier’s
2904𓀴brain-knowledge would be as nothing. If only there were a sufficient
2905𓀴cause! I must not think too much of this, or I may be tempted; a good
2906𓀴cause might turn the scale with me, for may not I too be of an
2907𓀴exceptional brain, congenitally?
2908𓀴
2909𓀴How well the man reasoned; lunatics always do within their own scope. I
2910𓀴wonder at how many lives he values a man, or if at only one. He has
2911𓀴closed the account most accurately, and to-day begun a new record. How
2912𓀴many of us begin a new record with each day of our lives?
2913𓀴
2914𓀴To me it seems only yesterday that my whole life ended with my new hope,
2915𓀴and that truly I began a new record. So it will be until the Great
2916𓀴Recorder sums me up and closes my ledger account with a balance to
2917𓀴profit or loss. Oh, Lucy, Lucy, I cannot be angry with you, nor can I be
2918𓀴angry with my friend whose happiness is yours; but I must only wait on
2919𓀴hopeless and work. Work! work!
2920𓀴
2921𓀴If I only could have as strong a cause as my poor mad friend there--a
2922𓀴good, unselfish cause to make me work--that would be indeed happiness.
2923𓀴
2924𓀴
2925𓀴_Mina Murray’s Journal._
2926𓀴
2927𓀴_26 July._--I am anxious, and it soothes me to express myself here; it
2928𓀴is like whispering to one’s self and listening at the same time. And
2929𓀴there is also something about the shorthand symbols that makes it
2930𓀴different from writing. I am unhappy about Lucy and about Jonathan. I
2931𓀴had not heard from Jonathan for some time, and was very concerned; but
2932𓀴yesterday dear Mr. Hawkins, who is always so kind, sent me a letter from
2933𓀴him. I had written asking him if he had heard, and he said the enclosed
2934𓀴had just been received. It is only a line dated from Castle Dracula,
2935𓀴and says that he is just starting for home. That is not like Jonathan;
2936𓀴I do not understand it, and it makes me uneasy. Then, too, Lucy,
2937𓀴although she is so well, has lately taken to her old habit of walking in
2938𓀴her sleep. Her mother has spoken to me about it, and we have decided
2939𓀴that I am to lock the door of our room every night. Mrs. Westenra has
2940𓀴got an idea that sleep-walkers always go out on roofs of houses and
2941𓀴along the edges of cliffs and then get suddenly wakened and fall over
2942𓀴with a despairing cry that echoes all over the place. Poor dear, she is
2943𓀴naturally anxious about Lucy, and she tells me that her husband, Lucy’s
2944𓀴father, had the same habit; that he would get up in the night and dress
2945𓀴himself and go out, if he were not stopped. Lucy is to be married in the
2946𓀴autumn, and she is already planning out her dresses and how her house is
2947𓀴to be arranged. I sympathise with her, for I do the same, only Jonathan
2948𓀴and I will start in life in a very simple way, and shall have to try to
2949𓀴make both ends meet. Mr. Holmwood--he is the Hon. Arthur Holmwood, only
2950𓀴son of Lord Godalming--is coming up here very shortly--as soon as he can
2951𓀴leave town, for his father is not very well, and I think dear Lucy is
2952𓀴counting the moments till he comes. She wants to take him up to the seat
2953𓀴on the churchyard cliff and show him the beauty of Whitby. I daresay it
2954𓀴is the waiting which disturbs her; she will be all right when he
2955𓀴arrives.
2956𓀴
2957𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
2958𓀴
2959𓀴_27 July._--No news from Jonathan. I am getting quite uneasy about him,
2960𓀴though why I should I do not know; but I do wish that he would write, if
2961𓀴it were only a single line. Lucy walks more than ever, and each night I
2962𓀴am awakened by her moving about the room. Fortunately, the weather is so
2963𓀴hot that she cannot get cold; but still the anxiety and the perpetually
2964𓀴being wakened is beginning to tell on me, and I am getting nervous and
2965𓀴wakeful myself. Thank God, Lucy’s health keeps up. Mr. Holmwood has been
2966𓀴suddenly called to Ring to see his father, who has been taken seriously
2967𓀴ill. Lucy frets at the postponement of seeing him, but it does not touch
2968𓀴her looks; she is a trifle stouter, and her cheeks are a lovely
2969𓀴rose-pink. She has lost that anæmic look which she had. I pray it will
2970𓀴all last.
2971𓀴
2972𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
2973𓀴
2974𓀴_3 August._--Another week gone, and no news from Jonathan, not even to
2975𓀴Mr. Hawkins, from whom I have heard. Oh, I do hope he is not ill. He
2976𓀴surely would have written. I look at that last letter of his, but
2977𓀴somehow it does not satisfy me. It does not read like him, and yet it is
2978𓀴his writing. There is no mistake of that. Lucy has not walked much in
2979𓀴her sleep the last week, but there is an odd concentration about her
2980𓀴which I do not understand; even in her sleep she seems to be watching
2981𓀴me. She tries the door, and finding it locked, goes about the room
2982𓀴searching for the key.
2983𓀴
2984𓀴_6 August._--Another three days, and no news. This suspense is getting
2985𓀴dreadful. If I only knew where to write to or where to go to, I should
2986𓀴feel easier; but no one has heard a word of Jonathan since that last
2987𓀴letter. I must only pray to God for patience. Lucy is more excitable
2988𓀴than ever, but is otherwise well. Last night was very threatening, and
2989𓀴the fishermen say that we are in for a storm. I must try to watch it and
2990𓀴learn the weather signs. To-day is a grey day, and the sun as I write is
2991𓀴hidden in thick clouds, high over Kettleness. Everything is grey--except
2992𓀴the green grass, which seems like emerald amongst it; grey earthy rock;
2993𓀴grey clouds, tinged with the sunburst at the far edge, hang over the
2994𓀴grey sea, into which the sand-points stretch like grey fingers. The sea
2995𓀴is tumbling in over the shallows and the sandy flats with a roar,
2996𓀴muffled in the sea-mists drifting inland. The horizon is lost in a grey
2997𓀴mist. All is vastness; the clouds are piled up like giant rocks, and
2998𓀴there is a “brool” over the sea that sounds like some presage of doom.
2999𓀴Dark figures are on the beach here and there, sometimes half shrouded in
3000𓀴the mist, and seem “men like trees walking.” The fishing-boats are
3001𓀴racing for home, and rise and dip in the ground swell as they sweep into
3002𓀴the harbour, bending to the scuppers. Here comes old Mr. Swales. He is
3003𓀴making straight for me, and I can see, by the way he lifts his hat, that
3004𓀴he wants to talk....
3005𓀴
3006𓀴I have been quite touched by the change in the poor old man. When he sat
3007𓀴down beside me, he said in a very gentle way:--
3008𓀴
3009𓀴“I want to say something to you, miss.” I could see he was not at ease,
3010𓀴so I took his poor old wrinkled hand in mine and asked him to speak
3011𓀴fully; so he said, leaving his hand in mine:--
3012𓀴
3013𓀴“I’m afraid, my deary, that I must have shocked you by all the wicked
3014𓀴things I’ve been sayin’ about the dead, and such like, for weeks past;
3015𓀴but I didn’t mean them, and I want ye to remember that when I’m gone. We
3016𓀴aud folks that be daffled, and with one foot abaft the krok-hooal, don’t
3017𓀴altogether like to think of it, and we don’t want to feel scart of it;
3018𓀴an’ that’s why I’ve took to makin’ light of it, so that I’d cheer up my
3019𓀴own heart a bit. But, Lord love ye, miss, I ain’t afraid of dyin’, not a
3020𓀴bit; only I don’t want to die if I can help it. My time must be nigh at
3021𓀴hand now, for I be aud, and a hundred years is too much for any man to
3022𓀴expect; and I’m so nigh it that the Aud Man is already whettin’ his
3023𓀴scythe. Ye see, I can’t get out o’ the habit of caffin’ about it all at
3024𓀴once; the chafts will wag as they be used to. Some day soon the Angel of
3025𓀴Death will sound his trumpet for me. But don’t ye dooal an’ greet, my
3026𓀴deary!”--for he saw that I was crying--“if he should come this very
3027𓀴night I’d not refuse to answer his call. For life be, after all, only a
3028𓀴waitin’ for somethin’ else than what we’re doin’; and death be all that
3029𓀴we can rightly depend on. But I’m content, for it’s comin’ to me, my
3030𓀴deary, and comin’ quick. It may be comin’ while we be lookin’ and
3031𓀴wonderin’. Maybe it’s in that wind out over the sea that’s bringin’ with
3032𓀴it loss and wreck, and sore distress, and sad hearts. Look! look!” he
3033𓀴cried suddenly. “There’s something in that wind and in the hoast beyont
3034𓀴that sounds, and looks, and tastes, and smells like death. It’s in the
3035𓀴air; I feel it comin’. Lord, make me answer cheerful when my call
3036𓀴comes!” He held up his arms devoutly, and raised his hat. His mouth
3037𓀴moved as though he were praying. After a few minutes’ silence, he got
3038𓀴up, shook hands with me, and blessed me, and said good-bye, and hobbled
3039𓀴off. It all touched me, and upset me very much.
3040𓀴
3041𓀴I was glad when the coastguard came along, with his spy-glass under his
3042𓀴arm. He stopped to talk with me, as he always does, but all the time
3043𓀴kept looking at a strange ship.
3044𓀴
3045𓀴“I can’t make her out,” he said; “she’s a Russian, by the look of her;
3046𓀴but she’s knocking about in the queerest way. She doesn’t know her mind
3047𓀴a bit; she seems to see the storm coming, but can’t decide whether to
3048𓀴run up north in the open, or to put in here. Look there again! She is
3049𓀴steered mighty strangely, for she doesn’t mind the hand on the wheel;
3050𓀴changes about with every puff of wind. We’ll hear more of her before
3051𓀴this time to-morrow.”
3052𓀴
3053𓀴
3054𓀴
3055𓀴
3056𓀴CHAPTER VII
3057𓀴
3058𓀴CUTTING FROM “THE DAILYGRAPH,” 8 AUGUST
3059𓀴
3060𓀴
3061𓀴(_Pasted in Mina Murray’s Journal._)
3062𓀴
3063𓀴From a Correspondent.
3064𓀴
3065𓀴_Whitby_.
3066𓀴
3067𓀴One of the greatest and suddenest storms on record has just been
3068𓀴experienced here, with results both strange and unique. The weather had
3069𓀴been somewhat sultry, but not to any degree uncommon in the month of
3070𓀴August. Saturday evening was as fine as was ever known, and the great
3071𓀴body of holiday-makers laid out yesterday for visits to Mulgrave Woods,
3072𓀴Robin Hood’s Bay, Rig Mill, Runswick, Staithes, and the various trips in
3073𓀴the neighbourhood of Whitby. The steamers _Emma_ and _Scarborough_ made
3074𓀴trips up and down the coast, and there was an unusual amount of
3075𓀴“tripping” both to and from Whitby. The day was unusually fine till the
3076𓀴afternoon, when some of the gossips who frequent the East Cliff
3077𓀴churchyard, and from that commanding eminence watch the wide sweep of
3078𓀴sea visible to the north and east, called attention to a sudden show of
3079𓀴“mares’-tails” high in the sky to the north-west. The wind was then
3080𓀴blowing from the south-west in the mild degree which in barometrical
3081𓀴language is ranked “No. 2: light breeze.” The coastguard on duty at once
3082𓀴made report, and one old fisherman, who for more than half a century has
3083𓀴kept watch on weather signs from the East Cliff, foretold in an emphatic
3084𓀴manner the coming of a sudden storm. The approach of sunset was so very
3085𓀴beautiful, so grand in its masses of splendidly-coloured clouds, that
3086𓀴there was quite an assemblage on the walk along the cliff in the old
3087𓀴churchyard to enjoy the beauty. Before the sun dipped below the black
3088𓀴mass of Kettleness, standing boldly athwart the western sky, its
3089𓀴downward way was marked by myriad clouds of every sunset-colour--flame,
3090𓀴purple, pink, green, violet, and all the tints of gold; with here and
3091𓀴there masses not large, but of seemingly absolute blackness, in all
3092𓀴sorts of shapes, as well outlined as colossal silhouettes. The
3093𓀴experience was not lost on the painters, and doubtless some of the
3094𓀴sketches of the “Prelude to the Great Storm” will grace the R. A. and R.
3095𓀴I. walls in May next. More than one captain made up his mind then and
3096𓀴there that his “cobble” or his “mule,” as they term the different
3097𓀴classes of boats, would remain in the harbour till the storm had passed.
3098𓀴The wind fell away entirely during the evening, and at midnight there
3099𓀴was a dead calm, a sultry heat, and that prevailing intensity which, on
3100𓀴the approach of thunder, affects persons of a sensitive nature. There
3101𓀴were but few lights in sight at sea, for even the coasting steamers,
3102𓀴which usually “hug” the shore so closely, kept well to seaward, and but
3103𓀴few fishing-boats were in sight. The only sail noticeable was a foreign
3104𓀴schooner with all sails set, which was seemingly going westwards. The
3105𓀴foolhardiness or ignorance of her officers was a prolific theme for
3106𓀴comment whilst she remained in sight, and efforts were made to signal
3107𓀴her to reduce sail in face of her danger. Before the night shut down she
3108𓀴was seen with sails idly flapping as she gently rolled on the undulating
3109𓀴swell of the sea,
3110𓀴
3111𓀴    “As idle as a painted ship upon a painted ocean.”
3112𓀴
3113𓀴Shortly before ten o’clock the stillness of the air grew quite
3114𓀴oppressive, and the silence was so marked that the bleating of a sheep
3115𓀴inland or the barking of a dog in the town was distinctly heard, and the
3116𓀴band on the pier, with its lively French air, was like a discord in the
3117𓀴great harmony of nature’s silence. A little after midnight came a
3118𓀴strange sound from over the sea, and high overhead the air began to
3119𓀴carry a strange, faint, hollow booming.
3120𓀴
3121𓀴Then without warning the tempest broke. With a rapidity which, at the
3122𓀴time, seemed incredible, and even afterwards is impossible to realize,
3123𓀴the whole aspect of nature at once became convulsed. The waves rose in
3124𓀴growing fury, each overtopping its fellow, till in a very few minutes
3125𓀴the lately glassy sea was like a roaring and devouring monster.
3126𓀴White-crested waves beat madly on the level sands and rushed up the
3127𓀴shelving cliffs; others broke over the piers, and with their spume swept
3128𓀴the lanthorns of the lighthouses which rise from the end of either pier
3129𓀴of Whitby Harbour. The wind roared like thunder, and blew with such
3130𓀴force that it was with difficulty that even strong men kept their feet,
3131𓀴or clung with grim clasp to the iron stanchions. It was found necessary
3132𓀴to clear the entire piers from the mass of onlookers, or else the
3133𓀴fatalities of the night would have been increased manifold. To add to
3134𓀴the difficulties and dangers of the time, masses of sea-fog came
3135𓀴drifting inland--white, wet clouds, which swept by in ghostly fashion,
3136𓀴so dank and damp and cold that it needed but little effort of
3137𓀴imagination to think that the spirits of those lost at sea were
3138𓀴touching their living brethren with the clammy hands of death, and many
3139𓀴a one shuddered as the wreaths of sea-mist swept by. At times the mist
3140𓀴cleared, and the sea for some distance could be seen in the glare of the
3141𓀴lightning, which now came thick and fast, followed by such sudden peals
3142𓀴of thunder that the whole sky overhead seemed trembling under the shock
3143𓀴of the footsteps of the storm.
3144𓀴
3145𓀴Some of the scenes thus revealed were of immeasurable grandeur and of
3146𓀴absorbing interest--the sea, running mountains high, threw skywards with
3147𓀴each wave mighty masses of white foam, which the tempest seemed to
3148𓀴snatch at and whirl away into space; here and there a fishing-boat, with
3149𓀴a rag of sail, running madly for shelter before the blast; now and again
3150𓀴the white wings of a storm-tossed sea-bird. On the summit of the East
3151𓀴Cliff the new searchlight was ready for experiment, but had not yet been
3152𓀴tried. The officers in charge of it got it into working order, and in
3153𓀴the pauses of the inrushing mist swept with it the surface of the sea.
3154𓀴Once or twice its service was most effective, as when a fishing-boat,
3155𓀴with gunwale under water, rushed into the harbour, able, by the guidance
3156𓀴of the sheltering light, to avoid the danger of dashing against the
3157𓀴piers. As each boat achieved the safety of the port there was a shout of
3158𓀴joy from the mass of people on shore, a shout which for a moment seemed
3159𓀴to cleave the gale and was then swept away in its rush.
3160𓀴
3161𓀴Before long the searchlight discovered some distance away a schooner
3162𓀴with all sails set, apparently the same vessel which had been noticed
3163𓀴earlier in the evening. The wind had by this time backed to the east,
3164𓀴and there was a shudder amongst the watchers on the cliff as they
3165𓀴realized the terrible danger in which she now was. Between her and the
3166𓀴port lay the great flat reef on which so many good ships have from time
3167𓀴to time suffered, and, with the wind blowing from its present quarter,
3168𓀴it would be quite impossible that she should fetch the entrance of the
3169𓀴harbour. It was now nearly the hour of high tide, but the waves were so
3170𓀴great that in their troughs the shallows of the shore were almost
3171𓀴visible, and the schooner, with all sails set, was rushing with such
3172𓀴speed that, in the words of one old salt, “she must fetch up somewhere,
3173𓀴if it was only in hell.” Then came another rush of sea-fog, greater than
3174𓀴any hitherto--a mass of dank mist, which seemed to close on all things
3175𓀴like a grey pall, and left available to men only the organ of hearing,
3176𓀴for the roar of the tempest, and the crash of the thunder, and the
3177𓀴booming of the mighty billows came through the damp oblivion even louder
3178𓀴than before. The rays of the searchlight were kept fixed on the harbour
3179𓀴mouth across the East Pier, where the shock was expected, and men waited
3180𓀴breathless. The wind suddenly shifted to the north-east, and the remnant
3181𓀴of the sea-fog melted in the blast; and then, _mirabile dictu_, between
3182𓀴the piers, leaping from wave to wave as it rushed at headlong speed,
3183𓀴swept the strange schooner before the blast, with all sail set, and
3184𓀴gained the safety of the harbour. The searchlight followed her, and a
3185𓀴shudder ran through all who saw her, for lashed to the helm was a
3186𓀴corpse, with drooping head, which swung horribly to and fro at each
3187𓀴motion of the ship. No other form could be seen on deck at all. A great
3188𓀴awe came on all as they realised that the ship, as if by a miracle, had
3189𓀴found the harbour, unsteered save by the hand of a dead man! However,
3190𓀴all took place more quickly than it takes to write these words. The
3191𓀴schooner paused not, but rushing across the harbour, pitched herself on
3192𓀴that accumulation of sand and gravel washed by many tides and many
3193𓀴storms into the south-east corner of the pier jutting under the East
3194𓀴Cliff, known locally as Tate Hill Pier.
3195𓀴
3196𓀴There was of course a considerable concussion as the vessel drove up on
3197𓀴the sand heap. Every spar, rope, and stay was strained, and some of the
3198𓀴“top-hammer” came crashing down. But, strangest of all, the very instant
3199𓀴the shore was touched, an immense dog sprang up on deck from below, as
3200𓀴if shot up by the concussion, and running forward, jumped from the bow
3201𓀴on the sand. Making straight for the steep cliff, where the churchyard
3202𓀴hangs over the laneway to the East Pier so steeply that some of the flat
3203𓀴tombstones--“thruff-steans” or “through-stones,” as they call them in
3204𓀴the Whitby vernacular--actually project over where the sustaining cliff
3205𓀴has fallen away, it disappeared in the darkness, which seemed
3206𓀴intensified just beyond the focus of the searchlight.
3207𓀴
3208𓀴It so happened that there was no one at the moment on Tate Hill Pier, as
3209𓀴all those whose houses are in close proximity were either in bed or were
3210𓀴out on the heights above. Thus the coastguard on duty on the eastern
3211𓀴side of the harbour, who at once ran down to the little pier, was the
3212𓀴first to climb on board. The men working the searchlight, after scouring
3213𓀴the entrance of the harbour without seeing anything, then turned the
3214𓀴light on the derelict and kept it there. The coastguard ran aft, and
3215𓀴when he came beside the wheel, bent over to examine it, and recoiled at
3216𓀴once as though under some sudden emotion. This seemed to pique general
3217𓀴curiosity, and quite a number of people began to run. It is a good way
3218𓀴round from the West Cliff by the Drawbridge to Tate Hill Pier, but your
3219𓀴correspondent is a fairly good runner, and came well ahead of the crowd.
3220𓀴When I arrived, however, I found already assembled on the pier a crowd,
3221𓀴whom the coastguard and police refused to allow to come on board. By the
3222𓀴courtesy of the chief boatman, I was, as your correspondent, permitted
3223𓀴to climb on deck, and was one of a small group who saw the dead seaman
3224𓀴whilst actually lashed to the wheel.
3225𓀴
3226𓀴It was no wonder that the coastguard was surprised, or even awed, for
3227𓀴not often can such a sight have been seen. The man was simply fastened
3228𓀴by his hands, tied one over the other, to a spoke of the wheel. Between
3229𓀴the inner hand and the wood was a crucifix, the set of beads on which it
3230𓀴was fastened being around both wrists and wheel, and all kept fast by
3231𓀴the binding cords. The poor fellow may have been seated at one time, but
3232𓀴the flapping and buffeting of the sails had worked through the rudder of
3233𓀴the wheel and dragged him to and fro, so that the cords with which he
3234𓀴was tied had cut the flesh to the bone. Accurate note was made of the
3235𓀴state of things, and a doctor--Surgeon J. M. Caffyn, of 33, East Elliot
3236𓀴Place--who came immediately after me, declared, after making
3237𓀴examination, that the man must have been dead for quite two days. In his
3238𓀴pocket was a bottle, carefully corked, empty save for a little roll of
3239𓀴paper, which proved to be the addendum to the log. The coastguard said
3240𓀴the man must have tied up his own hands, fastening the knots with his
3241𓀴teeth. The fact that a coastguard was the first on board may save some
3242𓀴complications, later on, in the Admiralty Court; for coastguards cannot
3243𓀴claim the salvage which is the right of the first civilian entering on a
3244𓀴derelict. Already, however, the legal tongues are wagging, and one young
3245𓀴law student is loudly asserting that the rights of the owner are already
3246𓀴completely sacrificed, his property being held in contravention of the
3247𓀴statutes of mortmain, since the tiller, as emblemship, if not proof, of
3248𓀴delegated possession, is held in a _dead hand_. It is needless to say
3249𓀴that the dead steersman has been reverently removed from the place where
3250𓀴he held his honourable watch and ward till death--a steadfastness as
3251𓀴noble as that of the young Casabianca--and placed in the mortuary to
3252𓀴await inquest.
3253𓀴
3254𓀴Already the sudden storm is passing, and its fierceness is abating;
3255𓀴crowds are scattering homeward, and the sky is beginning to redden over
3256𓀴the Yorkshire wolds. I shall send, in time for your next issue, further
3257𓀴details of the derelict ship which found her way so miraculously into
3258𓀴harbour in the storm.
3259𓀴
3260𓀴_Whitby_
3261𓀴
3262𓀴_9 August._--The sequel to the strange arrival of the derelict in the
3263𓀴storm last night is almost more startling than the thing itself. It
3264𓀴turns out that the schooner is a Russian from Varna, and is called the
3265𓀴_Demeter_. She is almost entirely in ballast of silver sand, with only a
3266𓀴small amount of cargo--a number of great wooden boxes filled with mould.
3267𓀴This cargo was consigned to a Whitby solicitor, Mr. S. F. Billington, of
3268𓀴7, The Crescent, who this morning went aboard and formally took
3269𓀴possession of the goods consigned to him. The Russian consul, too,
3270𓀴acting for the charter-party, took formal possession of the ship, and
3271𓀴paid all harbour dues, etc. Nothing is talked about here to-day except
3272𓀴the strange coincidence; the officials of the Board of Trade have been
3273𓀴most exacting in seeing that every compliance has been made with
3274𓀴existing regulations. As the matter is to be a “nine days’ wonder,” they
3275𓀴are evidently determined that there shall be no cause of after
3276𓀴complaint. A good deal of interest was abroad concerning the dog which
3277𓀴landed when the ship struck, and more than a few of the members of the
3278𓀴S. P. C. A., which is very strong in Whitby, have tried to befriend the
3279𓀴animal. To the general disappointment, however, it was not to be found;
3280𓀴it seems to have disappeared entirely from the town. It may be that it
3281𓀴was frightened and made its way on to the moors, where it is still
3282𓀴hiding in terror. There are some who look with dread on such a
3283𓀴possibility, lest later on it should in itself become a danger, for it
3284𓀴is evidently a fierce brute. Early this morning a large dog, a half-bred
3285𓀴mastiff belonging to a coal merchant close to Tate Hill Pier, was found
3286𓀴dead in the roadway opposite to its master’s yard. It had been fighting,
3287𓀴and manifestly had had a savage opponent, for its throat was torn away,
3288𓀴and its belly was slit open as if with a savage claw.
3289𓀴
3290𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
3291𓀴
3292𓀴_Later._--By the kindness of the Board of Trade inspector, I have been
3293𓀴permitted to look over the log-book of the _Demeter_, which was in order
3294𓀴up to within three days, but contained nothing of special interest
3295𓀴except as to facts of missing men. The greatest interest, however, is
3296𓀴with regard to the paper found in the bottle, which was to-day produced
3297𓀴at the inquest; and a more strange narrative than the two between them
3298𓀴unfold it has not been my lot to come across. As there is no motive for
3299𓀴concealment, I am permitted to use them, and accordingly send you a
3300𓀴rescript, simply omitting technical details of seamanship and
3301𓀴supercargo. It almost seems as though the captain had been seized with
3302𓀴some kind of mania before he had got well into blue water, and that
3303𓀴this had developed persistently throughout the voyage. Of course my
3304𓀴statement must be taken _cum grano_, since I am writing from the
3305𓀴dictation of a clerk of the Russian consul, who kindly translated for
3306𓀴me, time being short.
3307𓀴
3308𓀴                         LOG OF THE “DEMETER.”
3309𓀴
3310𓀴
3311𓀴_Varna to Whitby._
3312𓀴
3313𓀴_Written 18 July, things so strange happening, that I shall keep
3314𓀴accurate note henceforth till we land._
3315𓀴
3316𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
3317𓀴
3318𓀴On 6 July we finished taking in cargo, silver sand and boxes of earth.
3319𓀴At noon set sail. East wind, fresh. Crew, five hands ... two mates,
3320𓀴cook, and myself (captain).
3321𓀴
3322𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
3323𓀴
3324𓀴On 11 July at dawn entered Bosphorus. Boarded by Turkish Customs
3325𓀴officers. Backsheesh. All correct. Under way at 4 p. m.
3326𓀴
3327𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
3328𓀴
3329𓀴On 12 July through Dardanelles. More Customs officers and flagboat of
3330𓀴guarding squadron. Backsheesh again. Work of officers thorough, but
3331𓀴quick. Want us off soon. At dark passed into Archipelago.
3332𓀴
3333𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
3334𓀴
3335𓀴On 13 July passed Cape Matapan. Crew dissatisfied about something.
3336𓀴Seemed scared, but would not speak out.
3337𓀴
3338𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
3339𓀴
3340𓀴On 14 July was somewhat anxious about crew. Men all steady fellows, who
3341𓀴sailed with me before. Mate could not make out what was wrong; they only
3342𓀴told him there was _something_, and crossed themselves. Mate lost temper
3343𓀴with one of them that day and struck him. Expected fierce quarrel, but
3344𓀴all was quiet.
3345𓀴
3346𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
3347𓀴
3348𓀴On 16 July mate reported in the morning that one of crew, Petrofsky, was
3349𓀴missing. Could not account for it. Took larboard watch eight bells last
3350𓀴night; was relieved by Abramoff, but did not go to bunk. Men more
3351𓀴downcast than ever. All said they expected something of the kind, but
3352𓀴would not say more than there was _something_ aboard. Mate getting very
3353𓀴impatient with them; feared some trouble ahead.
3354𓀴
3355𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
3356𓀴
3357𓀴On 17 July, yesterday, one of the men, Olgaren, came to my cabin, and in
3358𓀴an awestruck way confided to me that he thought there was a strange man
3359𓀴aboard the ship. He said that in his watch he had been sheltering
3360𓀴behind the deck-house, as there was a rain-storm, when he saw a tall,
3361𓀴thin man, who was not like any of the crew, come up the companion-way,
3362𓀴and go along the deck forward, and disappear. He followed cautiously,
3363𓀴but when he got to bows found no one, and the hatchways were all closed.
3364𓀴He was in a panic of superstitious fear, and I am afraid the panic may
3365𓀴spread. To allay it, I shall to-day search entire ship carefully from
3366𓀴stem to stern.
3367𓀴
3368𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
3369𓀴
3370𓀴Later in the day I got together the whole crew, and told them, as they
3371𓀴evidently thought there was some one in the ship, we would search from
3372𓀴stem to stern. First mate angry; said it was folly, and to yield to such
3373𓀴foolish ideas would demoralise the men; said he would engage to keep
3374𓀴them out of trouble with a handspike. I let him take the helm, while the
3375𓀴rest began thorough search, all keeping abreast, with lanterns: we left
3376𓀴no corner unsearched. As there were only the big wooden boxes, there
3377𓀴were no odd corners where a man could hide. Men much relieved when
3378𓀴search over, and went back to work cheerfully. First mate scowled, but
3379𓀴said nothing.
3380𓀴
3381𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
3382𓀴
3383𓀴_22 July_.--Rough weather last three days, and all hands busy with
3384𓀴sails--no time to be frightened. Men seem to have forgotten their dread.
3385𓀴Mate cheerful again, and all on good terms. Praised men for work in bad
3386𓀴weather. Passed Gibralter and out through Straits. All well.
3387𓀴
3388𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
3389𓀴
3390𓀴_24 July_.--There seems some doom over this ship. Already a hand short,
3391𓀴and entering on the Bay of Biscay with wild weather ahead, and yet last
3392𓀴night another man lost--disappeared. Like the first, he came off his
3393𓀴watch and was not seen again. Men all in a panic of fear; sent a round
3394𓀴robin, asking to have double watch, as they fear to be alone. Mate
3395𓀴angry. Fear there will be some trouble, as either he or the men will do
3396𓀴some violence.
3397𓀴
3398𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
3399𓀴
3400𓀴_28 July_.--Four days in hell, knocking about in a sort of maelstrom,
3401𓀴and the wind a tempest. No sleep for any one. Men all worn out. Hardly
3402𓀴know how to set a watch, since no one fit to go on. Second mate
3403𓀴volunteered to steer and watch, and let men snatch a few hours’ sleep.
3404𓀴Wind abating; seas still terrific, but feel them less, as ship is
3405𓀴steadier.
3406𓀴
3407𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
3408𓀴
3409𓀴_29 July_.--Another tragedy. Had single watch to-night, as crew too
3410𓀴tired to double. When morning watch came on deck could find no one
3411𓀴except steersman. Raised outcry, and all came on deck. Thorough search,
3412𓀴but no one found. Are now without second mate, and crew in a panic. Mate
3413𓀴and I agreed to go armed henceforth and wait for any sign of cause.
3414𓀴
3415𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
3416𓀴
3417𓀴_30 July_.--Last night. Rejoiced we are nearing England. Weather fine,
3418𓀴all sails set. Retired worn out; slept soundly; awaked by mate telling
3419𓀴me that both man of watch and steersman missing. Only self and mate and
3420𓀴two hands left to work ship.
3421𓀴
3422𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
3423𓀴
3424𓀴_1 August_.--Two days of fog, and not a sail sighted. Had hoped when in
3425𓀴the English Channel to be able to signal for help or get in somewhere.
3426𓀴Not having power to work sails, have to run before wind. Dare not lower,
3427𓀴as could not raise them again. We seem to be drifting to some terrible
3428𓀴doom. Mate now more demoralised than either of men. His stronger nature
3429𓀴seems to have worked inwardly against himself. Men are beyond fear,
3430𓀴working stolidly and patiently, with minds made up to worst. They are
3431𓀴Russian, he Roumanian.
3432𓀴
3433𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
3434𓀴
3435𓀴_2 August, midnight_.--Woke up from few minutes’ sleep by hearing a cry,
3436𓀴seemingly outside my port. Could see nothing in fog. Rushed on deck, and
3437𓀴ran against mate. Tells me heard cry and ran, but no sign of man on
3438𓀴watch. One more gone. Lord, help us! Mate says we must be past Straits
3439𓀴of Dover, as in a moment of fog lifting he saw North Foreland, just as
3440𓀴he heard the man cry out. If so we are now off in the North Sea, and
3441𓀴only God can guide us in the fog, which seems to move with us; and God
3442𓀴seems to have deserted us.
3443𓀴
3444𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
3445𓀴
3446𓀴_3 August_.--At midnight I went to relieve the man at the wheel, and
3447𓀴when I got to it found no one there. The wind was steady, and as we ran
3448𓀴before it there was no yawing. I dared not leave it, so shouted for the
3449𓀴mate. After a few seconds he rushed up on deck in his flannels. He
3450𓀴looked wild-eyed and haggard, and I greatly fear his reason has given
3451𓀴way. He came close to me and whispered hoarsely, with his mouth to my
3452𓀴ear, as though fearing the very air might hear: “_It_ is here; I know
3453𓀴it, now. On the watch last night I saw It, like a man, tall and thin,
3454𓀴and ghastly pale. It was in the bows, and looking out. I crept behind
3455𓀴It, and gave It my knife; but the knife went through It, empty as the
3456𓀴air.” And as he spoke he took his knife and drove it savagely into
3457𓀴space. Then he went on: “But It is here, and I’ll find It. It is in the
3458𓀴hold, perhaps in one of those boxes. I’ll unscrew them one by one and
3459𓀴see. You work the helm.” And, with a warning look and his finger on his
3460𓀴lip, he went below. There was springing up a choppy wind, and I could
3461𓀴not leave the helm. I saw him come out on deck again with a tool-chest
3462𓀴and a lantern, and go down the forward hatchway. He is mad, stark,
3463𓀴raving mad, and it’s no use my trying to stop him. He can’t hurt those
3464𓀴big boxes: they are invoiced as “clay,” and to pull them about is as
3465𓀴harmless a thing as he can do. So here I stay, and mind the helm, and
3466𓀴write these notes. I can only trust in God and wait till the fog clears.
3467𓀴Then, if I can’t steer to any harbour with the wind that is, I shall cut
3468𓀴down sails and lie by, and signal for help....
3469𓀴
3470𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
3471𓀴
3472𓀴It is nearly all over now. Just as I was beginning to hope that the mate
3473𓀴would come out calmer--for I heard him knocking away at something in the
3474𓀴hold, and work is good for him--there came up the hatchway a sudden,
3475𓀴startled scream, which made my blood run cold, and up on the deck he
3476𓀴came as if shot from a gun--a raging madman, with his eyes rolling and
3477𓀴his face convulsed with fear. “Save me! save me!” he cried, and then
3478𓀴looked round on the blanket of fog. His horror turned to despair, and in
3479𓀴a steady voice he said: “You had better come too, captain, before it is
3480𓀴too late. _He_ is there. I know the secret now. The sea will save me
3481𓀴from Him, and it is all that is left!” Before I could say a word, or
3482𓀴move forward to seize him, he sprang on the bulwark and deliberately
3483𓀴threw himself into the sea. I suppose I know the secret too, now. It was
3484𓀴this madman who had got rid of the men one by one, and now he has
3485𓀴followed them himself. God help me! How am I to account for all these
3486𓀴horrors when I get to port? _When_ I get to port! Will that ever be?
3487𓀴
3488𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
3489𓀴
3490𓀴_4 August._--Still fog, which the sunrise cannot pierce. I know there is
3491𓀴sunrise because I am a sailor, why else I know not. I dared not go
3492𓀴below, I dared not leave the helm; so here all night I stayed, and in
3493𓀴the dimness of the night I saw It--Him! God forgive me, but the mate was
3494𓀴right to jump overboard. It was better to die like a man; to die like a
3495𓀴sailor in blue water no man can object. But I am captain, and I must not
3496𓀴leave my ship. But I shall baffle this fiend or monster, for I shall tie
3497𓀴my hands to the wheel when my strength begins to fail, and along with
3498𓀴them I shall tie that which He--It!--dare not touch; and then, come good
3499𓀴wind or foul, I shall save my soul, and my honour as a captain. I am
3500𓀴growing weaker, and the night is coming on. If He can look me in the
3501𓀴face again, I may not have time to act.... If we are wrecked, mayhap
3502𓀴this bottle may be found, and those who find it may understand; if not,
3503𓀴... well, then all men shall know that I have been true to my trust. God
3504𓀴and the Blessed Virgin and the saints help a poor ignorant soul trying
3505𓀴to do his duty....
3506𓀴
3507𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
3508𓀴
3509𓀴Of course the verdict was an open one. There is no evidence to adduce;
3510𓀴and whether or not the man himself committed the murders there is now
3511𓀴none to say. The folk here hold almost universally that the captain is
3512𓀴simply a hero, and he is to be given a public funeral. Already it is
3513𓀴arranged that his body is to be taken with a train of boats up the Esk
3514𓀴for a piece and then brought back to Tate Hill Pier and up the abbey
3515𓀴steps; for he is to be buried in the churchyard on the cliff. The owners
3516𓀴of more than a hundred boats have already given in their names as
3517𓀴wishing to follow him to the grave.
3518𓀴
3519𓀴No trace has ever been found of the great dog; at which there is much
3520𓀴mourning, for, with public opinion in its present state, he would, I
3521𓀴believe, be adopted by the town. To-morrow will see the funeral; and so
3522𓀴will end this one more “mystery of the sea.”
3523𓀴
3524𓀴
3525𓀴_Mina Murray’s Journal._
3526𓀴
3527𓀴_8 August._--Lucy was very restless all night, and I, too, could not
3528𓀴sleep. The storm was fearful, and as it boomed loudly among the
3529𓀴chimney-pots, it made me shudder. When a sharp puff came it seemed to be
3530𓀴like a distant gun. Strangely enough, Lucy did not wake; but she got up
3531𓀴twice and dressed herself. Fortunately, each time I awoke in time and
3532𓀴managed to undress her without waking her, and got her back to bed. It
3533𓀴is a very strange thing, this sleep-walking, for as soon as her will is
3534𓀴thwarted in any physical way, her intention, if there be any,
3535𓀴disappears, and she yields herself almost exactly to the routine of her
3536𓀴life.
3537𓀴
3538𓀴Early in the morning we both got up and went down to the harbour to see
3539𓀴if anything had happened in the night. There were very few people about,
3540𓀴and though the sun was bright, and the air clear and fresh, the big,
3541𓀴grim-looking waves, that seemed dark themselves because the foam that
3542𓀴topped them was like snow, forced themselves in through the narrow mouth
3543𓀴of the harbour--like a bullying man going through a crowd. Somehow I
3544𓀴felt glad that Jonathan was not on the sea last night, but on land. But,
3545𓀴oh, is he on land or sea? Where is he, and how? I am getting fearfully
3546𓀴anxious about him. If I only knew what to do, and could do anything!
3547𓀴
3548𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
3549𓀴
3550𓀴_10 August._--The funeral of the poor sea-captain to-day was most
3551𓀴touching. Every boat in the harbour seemed to be there, and the coffin
3552𓀴was carried by captains all the way from Tate Hill Pier up to the
3553𓀴churchyard. Lucy came with me, and we went early to our old seat, whilst
3554𓀴the cortège of boats went up the river to the Viaduct and came down
3555𓀴again. We had a lovely view, and saw the procession nearly all the way.
3556𓀴The poor fellow was laid to rest quite near our seat so that we stood on
3557𓀴it when the time came and saw everything. Poor Lucy seemed much upset.
3558𓀴She was restless and uneasy all the time, and I cannot but think that
3559𓀴her dreaming at night is telling on her. She is quite odd in one thing:
3560𓀴she will not admit to me that there is any cause for restlessness; or if
3561𓀴there be, she does not understand it herself. There is an additional
3562𓀴cause in that poor old Mr. Swales was found dead this morning on our
3563𓀴seat, his neck being broken. He had evidently, as the doctor said,
3564𓀴fallen back in the seat in some sort of fright, for there was a look of
3565𓀴fear and horror on his face that the men said made them shudder. Poor
3566𓀴dear old man! Perhaps he had seen Death with his dying eyes! Lucy is so
3567𓀴sweet and sensitive that she feels influences more acutely than other
3568𓀴people do. Just now she was quite upset by a little thing which I did
3569𓀴not much heed, though I am myself very fond of animals. One of the men
3570𓀴who came up here often to look for the boats was followed by his dog.
3571𓀴The dog is always with him. They are both quiet persons, and I never saw
3572𓀴the man angry, nor heard the dog bark. During the service the dog would
3573𓀴not come to its master, who was on the seat with us, but kept a few
3574𓀴yards off, barking and howling. Its master spoke to it gently, and then
3575𓀴harshly, and then angrily; but it would neither come nor cease to make a
3576𓀴noise. It was in a sort of fury, with its eyes savage, and all its hairs
3577𓀴bristling out like a cat’s tail when puss is on the war-path. Finally
3578𓀴the man, too, got angry, and jumped down and kicked the dog, and then
3579𓀴took it by the scruff of the neck and half dragged and half threw it on
3580𓀴the tombstone on which the seat is fixed. The moment it touched the
3581𓀴stone the poor thing became quiet and fell all into a tremble. It did
3582𓀴not try to get away, but crouched down, quivering and cowering, and was
3583𓀴in such a pitiable state of terror that I tried, though without effect,
3584𓀴to comfort it. Lucy was full of pity, too, but she did not attempt to
3585𓀴touch the dog, but looked at it in an agonised sort of way. I greatly
3586𓀴fear that she is of too super-sensitive a nature to go through the world
3587𓀴without trouble. She will be dreaming of this to-night, I am sure. The
3588𓀴whole agglomeration of things--the ship steered into port by a dead
3589𓀴man; his attitude, tied to the wheel with a crucifix and beads; the
3590𓀴touching funeral; the dog, now furious and now in terror--will all
3591𓀴afford material for her dreams.
3592𓀴
3593𓀴I think it will be best for her to go to bed tired out physically, so I
3594𓀴shall take her for a long walk by the cliffs to Robin Hood’s Bay and
3595𓀴back. She ought not to have much inclination for sleep-walking then.
3596𓀴
3597𓀴
3598𓀴
3599𓀴
3600𓀴CHAPTER VIII
3601𓀴
3602𓀴MINA MURRAY’S JOURNAL
3603𓀴
3604𓀴
3605𓀴_Same day, 11 o’clock p. m._--Oh, but I am tired! If it were not that I
3606𓀴had made my diary a duty I should not open it to-night. We had a lovely
3607𓀴walk. Lucy, after a while, was in gay spirits, owing, I think, to some
3608𓀴dear cows who came nosing towards us in a field close to the lighthouse,
3609𓀴and frightened the wits out of us. I believe we forgot everything
3610𓀴except, of course, personal fear, and it seemed to wipe the slate clean
3611𓀴and give us a fresh start. We had a capital “severe tea” at Robin Hood’s
3612𓀴Bay in a sweet little old-fashioned inn, with a bow-window right over
3613𓀴the seaweed-covered rocks of the strand. I believe we should have
3614𓀴shocked the “New Woman” with our appetites. Men are more tolerant, bless
3615𓀴them! Then we walked home with some, or rather many, stoppages to rest,
3616𓀴and with our hearts full of a constant dread of wild bulls. Lucy was
3617𓀴really tired, and we intended to creep off to bed as soon as we could.
3618𓀴The young curate came in, however, and Mrs. Westenra asked him to stay
3619𓀴for supper. Lucy and I had both a fight for it with the dusty miller; I
3620𓀴know it was a hard fight on my part, and I am quite heroic. I think that
3621𓀴some day the bishops must get together and see about breeding up a new
3622𓀴class of curates, who don’t take supper, no matter how they may be
3623𓀴pressed to, and who will know when girls are tired. Lucy is asleep and
3624𓀴breathing softly. She has more colour in her cheeks than usual, and
3625𓀴looks, oh, so sweet. If Mr. Holmwood fell in love with her seeing her
3626𓀴only in the drawing-room, I wonder what he would say if he saw her now.
3627𓀴Some of the “New Women” writers will some day start an idea that men and
3628𓀴women should be allowed to see each other asleep before proposing or
3629𓀴accepting. But I suppose the New Woman won’t condescend in future to
3630𓀴accept; she will do the proposing herself. And a nice job she will make
3631𓀴of it, too! There’s some consolation in that. I am so happy to-night,
3632𓀴because dear Lucy seems better. I really believe she has turned the
3633𓀴corner, and that we are over her troubles with dreaming. I should be
3634𓀴quite happy if I only knew if Jonathan.... God bless and keep him.
3635𓀴
3636𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
3637𓀴
3638𓀴_11 August, 3 a. m._--Diary again. No sleep now, so I may as well write.
3639𓀴I am too agitated to sleep. We have had such an adventure, such an
3640𓀴agonising experience. I fell asleep as soon as I had closed my diary....
3641𓀴Suddenly I became broad awake, and sat up, with a horrible sense of fear
3642𓀴upon me, and of some feeling of emptiness around me. The room was dark,
3643𓀴so I could not see Lucy’s bed; I stole across and felt for her. The bed
3644𓀴was empty. I lit a match and found that she was not in the room. The
3645𓀴door was shut, but not locked, as I had left it. I feared to wake her
3646𓀴mother, who has been more than usually ill lately, so threw on some
3647𓀴clothes and got ready to look for her. As I was leaving the room it
3648𓀴struck me that the clothes she wore might give me some clue to her
3649𓀴dreaming intention. Dressing-gown would mean house; dress, outside.
3650𓀴Dressing-gown and dress were both in their places. “Thank God,” I said
3651𓀴to myself, “she cannot be far, as she is only in her nightdress.” I ran
3652𓀴downstairs and looked in the sitting-room. Not there! Then I looked in
3653𓀴all the other open rooms of the house, with an ever-growing fear
3654𓀴chilling my heart. Finally I came to the hall door and found it open. It
3655𓀴was not wide open, but the catch of the lock had not caught. The people
3656𓀴of the house are careful to lock the door every night, so I feared that
3657𓀴Lucy must have gone out as she was. There was no time to think of what
3658𓀴might happen; a vague, overmastering fear obscured all details. I took a
3659𓀴big, heavy shawl and ran out. The clock was striking one as I was in the
3660𓀴Crescent, and there was not a soul in sight. I ran along the North
3661𓀴Terrace, but could see no sign of the white figure which I expected. At
3662𓀴the edge of the West Cliff above the pier I looked across the harbour to
3663𓀴the East Cliff, in the hope or fear--I don’t know which--of seeing Lucy
3664𓀴in our favourite seat. There was a bright full moon, with heavy black,
3665𓀴driving clouds, which threw the whole scene into a fleeting diorama of
3666𓀴light and shade as they sailed across. For a moment or two I could see
3667𓀴nothing, as the shadow of a cloud obscured St. Mary’s Church and all
3668𓀴around it. Then as the cloud passed I could see the ruins of the abbey
3669𓀴coming into view; and as the edge of a narrow band of light as sharp as
3670𓀴a sword-cut moved along, the church and the churchyard became gradually
3671𓀴visible. Whatever my expectation was, it was not disappointed, for
3672𓀴there, on our favourite seat, the silver light of the moon struck a
3673𓀴half-reclining figure, snowy white. The coming of the cloud was too
3674𓀴quick for me to see much, for shadow shut down on light almost
3675𓀴immediately; but it seemed to me as though something dark stood behind
3676𓀴the seat where the white figure shone, and bent over it. What it was,
3677𓀴whether man or beast, I could not tell; I did not wait to catch another
3678𓀴glance, but flew down the steep steps to the pier and along by the
3679𓀴fish-market to the bridge, which was the only way to reach the East
3680𓀴Cliff. The town seemed as dead, for not a soul did I see; I rejoiced
3681𓀴that it was so, for I wanted no witness of poor Lucy’s condition. The
3682𓀴time and distance seemed endless, and my knees trembled and my breath
3683𓀴came laboured as I toiled up the endless steps to the abbey. I must have
3684𓀴gone fast, and yet it seemed to me as if my feet were weighted with
3685𓀴lead, and as though every joint in my body were rusty. When I got almost
3686𓀴to the top I could see the seat and the white figure, for I was now
3687𓀴close enough to distinguish it even through the spells of shadow. There
3688𓀴was undoubtedly something, long and black, bending over the
3689𓀴half-reclining white figure. I called in fright, “Lucy! Lucy!” and
3690𓀴something raised a head, and from where I was I could see a white face
3691𓀴and red, gleaming eyes. Lucy did not answer, and I ran on to the
3692𓀴entrance of the churchyard. As I entered, the church was between me and
3693𓀴the seat, and for a minute or so I lost sight of her. When I came in
3694𓀴view again the cloud had passed, and the moonlight struck so brilliantly
3695𓀴that I could see Lucy half reclining with her head lying over the back
3696𓀴of the seat. She was quite alone, and there was not a sign of any living
3697𓀴thing about.
3698𓀴
3699𓀴When I bent over her I could see that she was still asleep. Her lips
3700𓀴were parted, and she was breathing--not softly as usual with her, but in
3701𓀴long, heavy gasps, as though striving to get her lungs full at every
3702𓀴breath. As I came close, she put up her hand in her sleep and pulled the
3703𓀴collar of her nightdress close around her throat. Whilst she did so
3704𓀴there came a little shudder through her, as though she felt the cold. I
3705𓀴flung the warm shawl over her, and drew the edges tight round her neck,
3706𓀴for I dreaded lest she should get some deadly chill from the night air,
3707𓀴unclad as she was. I feared to wake her all at once, so, in order to
3708𓀴have my hands free that I might help her, I fastened the shawl at her
3709𓀴throat with a big safety-pin; but I must have been clumsy in my anxiety
3710𓀴and pinched or pricked her with it, for by-and-by, when her breathing
3711𓀴became quieter, she put her hand to her throat again and moaned. When I
3712𓀴had her carefully wrapped up I put my shoes on her feet and then began
3713𓀴very gently to wake her. At first she did not respond; but gradually she
3714𓀴became more and more uneasy in her sleep, moaning and sighing
3715𓀴occasionally. At last, as time was passing fast, and, for many other
3716𓀴reasons, I wished to get her home at once, I shook her more forcibly,
3717𓀴till finally she opened her eyes and awoke. She did not seem surprised
3718𓀴to see me, as, of course, she did not realise all at once where she was.
3719𓀴Lucy always wakes prettily, and even at such a time, when her body must
3720𓀴have been chilled with cold, and her mind somewhat appalled at waking
3721𓀴unclad in a churchyard at night, she did not lose her grace. She
3722𓀴trembled a little, and clung to me; when I told her to come at once with
3723𓀴me home she rose without a word, with the obedience of a child. As we
3724𓀴passed along, the gravel hurt my feet, and Lucy noticed me wince. She
3725𓀴stopped and wanted to insist upon my taking my shoes; but I would not.
3726𓀴However, when we got to the pathway outside the churchyard, where there
3727𓀴was a puddle of water, remaining from the storm, I daubed my feet with
3728𓀴mud, using each foot in turn on the other, so that as we went home, no
3729𓀴one, in case we should meet any one, should notice my bare feet.
3730𓀴
3731𓀴Fortune favoured us, and we got home without meeting a soul. Once we saw
3732𓀴a man, who seemed not quite sober, passing along a street in front of
3733𓀴us; but we hid in a door till he had disappeared up an opening such as
3734𓀴there are here, steep little closes, or “wynds,” as they call them in
3735𓀴Scotland. My heart beat so loud all the time that sometimes I thought I
3736𓀴should faint. I was filled with anxiety about Lucy, not only for her
3737𓀴health, lest she should suffer from the exposure, but for her reputation
3738𓀴in case the story should get wind. When we got in, and had washed our
3739𓀴feet, and had said a prayer of thankfulness together, I tucked her into
3740𓀴bed. Before falling asleep she asked--even implored--me not to say a
3741𓀴word to any one, even her mother, about her sleep-walking adventure. I
3742𓀴hesitated at first to promise; but on thinking of the state of her
3743𓀴mother’s health, and how the knowledge of such a thing would fret her,
3744𓀴and thinking, too, of how such a story might become distorted--nay,
3745𓀴infallibly would--in case it should leak out, I thought it wiser to do
3746𓀴so. I hope I did right. I have locked the door, and the key is tied to
3747𓀴my wrist, so perhaps I shall not be again disturbed. Lucy is sleeping
3748𓀴soundly; the reflex of the dawn is high and far over the sea....
3749𓀴
3750𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
3751𓀴
3752𓀴_Same day, noon._--All goes well. Lucy slept till I woke her and seemed
3753𓀴not to have even changed her side. The adventure of the night does not
3754𓀴seem to have harmed her; on the contrary, it has benefited her, for she
3755𓀴looks better this morning than she has done for weeks. I was sorry to
3756𓀴notice that my clumsiness with the safety-pin hurt her. Indeed, it might
3757𓀴have been serious, for the skin of her throat was pierced. I must have
3758𓀴pinched up a piece of loose skin and have transfixed it, for there are
3759𓀴two little red points like pin-pricks, and on the band of her nightdress
3760𓀴was a drop of blood. When I apologised and was concerned about it, she
3761𓀴laughed and petted me, and said she did not even feel it. Fortunately it
3762𓀴cannot leave a scar, as it is so tiny.
3763𓀴
3764𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
3765𓀴
3766𓀴_Same day, night._--We passed a happy day. The air was clear, and the
3767𓀴sun bright, and there was a cool breeze. We took our lunch to Mulgrave
3768𓀴Woods, Mrs. Westenra driving by the road and Lucy and I walking by the
3769𓀴cliff-path and joining her at the gate. I felt a little sad myself, for
3770𓀴I could not but feel how _absolutely_ happy it would have been had
3771𓀴Jonathan been with me. But there! I must only be patient. In the evening
3772𓀴we strolled in the Casino Terrace, and heard some good music by Spohr
3773𓀴and Mackenzie, and went to bed early. Lucy seems more restful than she
3774𓀴has been for some time, and fell asleep at once. I shall lock the door
3775𓀴and secure the key the same as before, though I do not expect any
3776𓀴trouble to-night.
3777𓀴
3778𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
3779𓀴
3780𓀴_12 August._--My expectations were wrong, for twice during the night I
3781𓀴was wakened by Lucy trying to get out. She seemed, even in her sleep, to
3782𓀴be a little impatient at finding the door shut, and went back to bed
3783𓀴under a sort of protest. I woke with the dawn, and heard the birds
3784𓀴chirping outside of the window. Lucy woke, too, and, I was glad to see,
3785𓀴was even better than on the previous morning. All her old gaiety of
3786𓀴manner seemed to have come back, and she came and snuggled in beside me
3787𓀴and told me all about Arthur. I told her how anxious I was about
3788𓀴Jonathan, and then she tried to comfort me. Well, she succeeded
3789𓀴somewhat, for, though sympathy can’t alter facts, it can help to make
3790𓀴them more bearable.
3791𓀴
3792𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
3793𓀴
3794𓀴_13 August._--Another quiet day, and to bed with the key on my wrist as
3795𓀴before. Again I awoke in the night, and found Lucy sitting up in bed,
3796𓀴still asleep, pointing to the window. I got up quietly, and pulling
3797𓀴aside the blind, looked out. It was brilliant moonlight, and the soft
3798𓀴effect of the light over the sea and sky--merged together in one great,
3799𓀴silent mystery--was beautiful beyond words. Between me and the moonlight
3800𓀴flitted a great bat, coming and going in great whirling circles. Once or
3801𓀴twice it came quite close, but was, I suppose, frightened at seeing me,
3802𓀴and flitted away across the harbour towards the abbey. When I came back
3803𓀴from the window Lucy had lain down again, and was sleeping peacefully.
3804𓀴She did not stir again all night.
3805𓀴
3806𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
3807𓀴
3808𓀴_14 August._--On the East Cliff, reading and writing all day. Lucy seems
3809𓀴to have become as much in love with the spot as I am, and it is hard to
3810𓀴get her away from it when it is time to come home for lunch or tea or
3811𓀴dinner. This afternoon she made a funny remark. We were coming home for
3812𓀴dinner, and had come to the top of the steps up from the West Pier and
3813𓀴stopped to look at the view, as we generally do. The setting sun, low
3814𓀴down in the sky, was just dropping behind Kettleness; the red light was
3815𓀴thrown over on the East Cliff and the old abbey, and seemed to bathe
3816𓀴everything in a beautiful rosy glow. We were silent for a while, and
3817𓀴suddenly Lucy murmured as if to herself:--
3818𓀴
3819𓀴“His red eyes again! They are just the same.” It was such an odd
3820𓀴expression, coming _apropos_ of nothing, that it quite startled me. I
3821𓀴slewed round a little, so as to see Lucy well without seeming to stare
3822𓀴at her, and saw that she was in a half-dreamy state, with an odd look on
3823𓀴her face that I could not quite make out; so I said nothing, but
3824𓀴followed her eyes. She appeared to be looking over at our own seat,
3825𓀴whereon was a dark figure seated alone. I was a little startled myself,
3826𓀴for it seemed for an instant as if the stranger had great eyes like
3827𓀴burning flames; but a second look dispelled the illusion. The red
3828𓀴sunlight was shining on the windows of St. Mary’s Church behind our
3829𓀴seat, and as the sun dipped there was just sufficient change in the
3830𓀴refraction and reflection to make it appear as if the light moved. I
3831𓀴called Lucy’s attention to the peculiar effect, and she became herself
3832𓀴with a start, but she looked sad all the same; it may have been that she
3833𓀴was thinking of that terrible night up there. We never refer to it; so I
3834𓀴said nothing, and we went home to dinner. Lucy had a headache and went
3835𓀴early to bed. I saw her asleep, and went out for a little stroll myself;
3836𓀴I walked along the cliffs to the westward, and was full of sweet
3837𓀴sadness, for I was thinking of Jonathan. When coming home--it was then
3838𓀴bright moonlight, so bright that, though the front of our part of the
3839𓀴Crescent was in shadow, everything could be well seen--I threw a glance
3840𓀴up at our window, and saw Lucy’s head leaning out. I thought that
3841𓀴perhaps she was looking out for me, so I opened my handkerchief and
3842𓀴waved it. She did not notice or make any movement whatever. Just then,
3843𓀴the moonlight crept round an angle of the building, and the light fell
3844𓀴on the window. There distinctly was Lucy with her head lying up against
3845𓀴the side of the window-sill and her eyes shut. She was fast asleep, and
3846𓀴by her, seated on the window-sill, was something that looked like a
3847𓀴good-sized bird. I was afraid she might get a chill, so I ran upstairs,
3848𓀴but as I came into the room she was moving back to her bed, fast
3849𓀴asleep, and breathing heavily; she was holding her hand to her throat,
3850𓀴as though to protect it from cold.
3851𓀴
3852𓀴I did not wake her, but tucked her up warmly; I have taken care that the
3853𓀴door is locked and the window securely fastened.
3854𓀴
3855𓀴She looks so sweet as she sleeps; but she is paler than is her wont, and
3856𓀴there is a drawn, haggard look under her eyes which I do not like. I
3857𓀴fear she is fretting about something. I wish I could find out what it
3858𓀴is.
3859𓀴
3860𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
3861𓀴
3862𓀴_15 August._--Rose later than usual. Lucy was languid and tired, and
3863𓀴slept on after we had been called. We had a happy surprise at breakfast.
3864𓀴Arthur’s father is better, and wants the marriage to come off soon. Lucy
3865𓀴is full of quiet joy, and her mother is glad and sorry at once. Later on
3866𓀴in the day she told me the cause. She is grieved to lose Lucy as her
3867𓀴very own, but she is rejoiced that she is soon to have some one to
3868𓀴protect her. Poor dear, sweet lady! She confided to me that she has got
3869𓀴her death-warrant. She has not told Lucy, and made me promise secrecy;
3870𓀴her doctor told her that within a few months, at most, she must die, for
3871𓀴her heart is weakening. At any time, even now, a sudden shock would be
3872𓀴almost sure to kill her. Ah, we were wise to keep from her the affair of
3873𓀴the dreadful night of Lucy’s sleep-walking.
3874𓀴
3875𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
3876𓀴
3877𓀴_17 August._--No diary for two whole days. I have not had the heart to
3878𓀴write. Some sort of shadowy pall seems to be coming over our happiness.
3879𓀴No news from Jonathan, and Lucy seems to be growing weaker, whilst her
3880𓀴mother’s hours are numbering to a close. I do not understand Lucy’s
3881𓀴fading away as she is doing. She eats well and sleeps well, and enjoys
3882𓀴the fresh air; but all the time the roses in her cheeks are fading, and
3883𓀴she gets weaker and more languid day by day; at night I hear her gasping
3884𓀴as if for air. I keep the key of our door always fastened to my wrist at
3885𓀴night, but she gets up and walks about the room, and sits at the open
3886𓀴window. Last night I found her leaning out when I woke up, and when I
3887𓀴tried to wake her I could not; she was in a faint. When I managed to
3888𓀴restore her she was as weak as water, and cried silently between long,
3889𓀴painful struggles for breath. When I asked her how she came to be at the
3890𓀴window she shook her head and turned away. I trust her feeling ill may
3891𓀴not be from that unlucky prick of the safety-pin. I looked at her throat
3892𓀴just now as she lay asleep, and the tiny wounds seem not to have healed.
3893𓀴They are still open, and, if anything, larger than before, and the
3894𓀴edges of them are faintly white. They are like little white dots with
3895𓀴red centres. Unless they heal within a day or two, I shall insist on the
3896𓀴doctor seeing about them.
3897𓀴
3898𓀴

3907𓀴Railway. Same are to be delivered at Carfax, near Purfleet, immediately
3908𓀴on receipt at goods station King’s Cross. The house is at present empty,
3909𓀴but enclosed please find keys, all of which are labelled.
3910𓀴
3911𓀴“You will please deposit the boxes, fifty in number, which form the
3912𓀴consignment, in the partially ruined building forming part of the house
3913𓀴and marked ‘A’ on rough diagram enclosed. Your agent will easily
3914𓀴recognise the locality, as it is the ancient chapel of the mansion. The
3915𓀴goods leave by the train at 9:30 to-night, and will be due at King’s
3916𓀴Cross at 4:30 to-morrow afternoon. As our client wishes the delivery
3917𓀴made as soon as possible, we shall be obliged by your having teams ready
3918𓀴at King’s Cross at the time named and forthwith conveying the goods to
3919𓀴destination. In order to obviate any delays possible through any routine
3920𓀴requirements as to payment in your departments, we enclose cheque
3921𓀴herewith for ten pounds (£10), receipt of which please acknowledge.
3922𓀴Should the charge be less than this amount, you can return balance; if
3923𓀴greater, we shall at once send cheque for difference on hearing from
3924𓀴you. You are to leave the keys on coming away in the main hall of the
3925𓀴house, where the proprietor may get them on his entering the house by
3926𓀴means of his duplicate key.
3927𓀴
3928𓀴“Pray do not take us as exceeding the bounds of business courtesy in
3929𓀴pressing you in all ways to use the utmost expedition.

3957𓀴_Mina Murray’s Journal._
3958𓀴
3959𓀴_18 August._--I am happy to-day, and write sitting on the seat in the
3960𓀴churchyard. Lucy is ever so much better. Last night she slept well all
3961𓀴night, and did not disturb me once. The roses seem coming back already
3962𓀴to her cheeks, though she is still sadly pale and wan-looking. If she
3963𓀴were in any way anæmic I could understand it, but she is not. She is in
3964𓀴gay spirits and full of life and cheerfulness. All the morbid reticence
3965𓀴seems to have passed from her, and she has just reminded me, as if I
3966𓀴needed any reminding, of _that_ night, and that it was here, on this
3967𓀴very seat, I found her asleep. As she told me she tapped playfully with
3968𓀴the heel of her boot on the stone slab and said:--
3969𓀴
3970𓀴“My poor little feet didn’t make much noise then! I daresay poor old Mr.
3971𓀴Swales would have told me that it was because I didn’t want to wake up
3972𓀴Geordie.” As she was in such a communicative humour, I asked her if she
3973𓀴had dreamed at all that night. Before she answered, that sweet, puckered
3974𓀴look came into her forehead, which Arthur--I call him Arthur from her
3975𓀴habit--says he loves; and, indeed, I don’t wonder that he does. Then she
3976𓀴went on in a half-dreaming kind of way, as if trying to recall it to
3977𓀴herself:--
3978𓀴
3979𓀴“I didn’t quite dream; but it all seemed to be real. I only wanted to be
3980𓀴here in this spot--I don’t know why, for I was afraid of something--I
3981𓀴don’t know what. I remember, though I suppose I was asleep, passing
3982𓀴through the streets and over the bridge. A fish leaped as I went by, and
3983𓀴I leaned over to look at it, and I heard a lot of dogs howling--the
3984𓀴whole town seemed as if it must be full of dogs all howling at once--as
3985𓀴I went up the steps. Then I had a vague memory of something long and
3986𓀴dark with red eyes, just as we saw in the sunset, and something very
3987𓀴sweet and very bitter all around me at once; and then I seemed sinking
3988𓀴into deep green water, and there was a singing in my ears, as I have
