3989𓀴heard there is to drowning men; and then everything seemed passing away
3990𓀴from me; my soul seemed to go out from my body and float about the air.
3991𓀴I seem to remember that once the West Lighthouse was right under me,
3992𓀴and then there was a sort of agonising feeling, as if I were in an
3993𓀴earthquake, and I came back and found you shaking my body. I saw you do
3994𓀴it before I felt you.”
3995𓀴
3996𓀴Then she began to laugh. It seemed a little uncanny to me, and I
3997𓀴listened to her breathlessly. I did not quite like it, and thought it
3998𓀴better not to keep her mind on the subject, so we drifted on to other
3999𓀴subjects, and Lucy was like her old self again. When we got home the
4000𓀴fresh breeze had braced her up, and her pale cheeks were really more
4001𓀴rosy. Her mother rejoiced when she saw her, and we all spent a very
4002𓀴happy evening together.
4003𓀴
4004𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
4005𓀴
4006𓀴_19 August._--Joy, joy, joy! although not all joy. At last, news of
4007𓀴Jonathan. The dear fellow has been ill; that is why he did not write. I
4008𓀴am not afraid to think it or say it, now that I know. Mr. Hawkins sent
4009𓀴me on the letter, and wrote himself, oh, so kindly. I am to leave in the
4010𓀴morning and go over to Jonathan, and to help to nurse him if necessary,
4011𓀴and to bring him home. Mr. Hawkins says it would not be a bad thing if
4012𓀴we were to be married out there. I have cried over the good Sister’s
4013𓀴letter till I can feel it wet against my bosom, where it lies. It is of
4014𓀴Jonathan, and must be next my heart, for he is _in_ my heart. My journey
4015𓀴is all mapped out, and my luggage ready. I am only taking one change of
4016𓀴dress; Lucy will bring my trunk to London and keep it till I send for
4017𓀴it, for it may be that ... I must write no more; I must keep it to say
4018𓀴to Jonathan, my husband. The letter that he has seen and touched must
4019𓀴comfort me till we meet.
4020𓀴
4021𓀴
4022𓀴_Letter, Sister Agatha, Hospital of St. Joseph and Ste. Mary,
4023𓀴Buda-Pesth, to Miss Wilhelmina Murray._
4024𓀴
4025𓀴“_12 August._
4026𓀴
4027𓀴“Dear Madam,--
4028𓀴
4029𓀴“I write by desire of Mr. Jonathan Harker, who is himself not strong
4030𓀴enough to write, though progressing well, thanks to God and St. Joseph
4031𓀴and Ste. Mary. He has been under our care for nearly six weeks,
4032𓀴suffering from a violent brain fever. He wishes me to convey his love,
4033𓀴and to say that by this post I write for him to Mr. Peter Hawkins,
4034𓀴Exeter, to say, with his dutiful respects, that he is sorry for his
4035𓀴delay, and that all of his work is completed. He will require some few
4036𓀴weeks’ rest in our sanatorium in the hills, but will then return. He
4037𓀴wishes me to say that he has not sufficient money with him, and that he
4038𓀴would like to pay for his staying here, so that others who need shall
4039𓀴not be wanting for help.
4040𓀴
4041𓀴“Believe me,
4042𓀴
4043𓀴“Yours, with sympathy and all blessings,
4044𓀴
4045𓀴“SISTER AGATHA.
4046𓀴
4047𓀴“P. S.--My patient being asleep, I open this to let you know something
4048𓀴more. He has told me all about you, and that you are shortly to be his
4049𓀴wife. All blessings to you both! He has had some fearful shock--so says
4050𓀴our doctor--and in his delirium his ravings have been dreadful; of
4051𓀴wolves and poison and blood; of ghosts and demons; and I fear to say of
4052𓀴what. Be careful with him always that there may be nothing to excite him
4053𓀴of this kind for a long time to come; the traces of such an illness as
4054𓀴his do not lightly die away. We should have written long ago, but we
4055𓀴knew nothing of his friends, and there was on him nothing that any one
4056𓀴could understand. He came in the train from Klausenburg, and the guard
4057𓀴was told by the station-master there that he rushed into the station
4058𓀴shouting for a ticket for home. Seeing from his violent demeanour that
4059𓀴he was English, they gave him a ticket for the furthest station on the
4060𓀴way thither that the train reached.
4061𓀴
4062𓀴“Be assured that he is well cared for. He has won all hearts by his
4063𓀴sweetness and gentleness. He is truly getting on well, and I have no
4064𓀴doubt will in a few weeks be all himself. But be careful of him for
4065𓀴safety’s sake. There are, I pray God and St. Joseph and Ste. Mary, many,
4066𓀴many, happy years for you both.”
4067𓀴
4068𓀴
4069𓀴_Dr. Seward’s Diary._
4070𓀴
4071𓀴_19 August._--Strange and sudden change in Renfield last night. About
4072𓀴eight o’clock he began to get excited and sniff about as a dog does when
4073𓀴setting. The attendant was struck by his manner, and knowing my interest
4074𓀴in him, encouraged him to talk. He is usually respectful to the
4075𓀴attendant and at times servile; but to-night, the man tells me, he was
4076𓀴quite haughty. Would not condescend to talk with him at all. All he
4077𓀴would say was:--
4078𓀴
4079𓀴     “I don’t want to talk to you: you don’t count now; the Master is at
4080𓀴     hand.”
4081𓀴
4082𓀴The attendant thinks it is some sudden form of religious mania which has
4083𓀴seized him. If so, we must look out for squalls, for a strong man with
4084𓀴homicidal and religious mania at once might be dangerous. The
4085𓀴combination is a dreadful one. At nine o’clock I visited him myself. His
4086𓀴attitude to me was the same as that to the attendant; in his sublime
4087𓀴self-feeling the difference between myself and attendant seemed to him
4088𓀴as nothing. It looks like religious mania, and he will soon think that
4089𓀴he himself is God. These infinitesimal distinctions between man and man
4090𓀴are too paltry for an Omnipotent Being. How these madmen give themselves
4091𓀴away! The real God taketh heed lest a sparrow fall; but the God created
4092𓀴from human vanity sees no difference between an eagle and a sparrow. Oh,
4093𓀴if men only knew!
4094𓀴
4095𓀴For half an hour or more Renfield kept getting excited in greater and
4096𓀴greater degree. I did not pretend to be watching him, but I kept strict
4097𓀴observation all the same. All at once that shifty look came into his
4098𓀴eyes which we always see when a madman has seized an idea, and with it
4099𓀴the shifty movement of the head and back which asylum attendants come to
4100𓀴know so well. He became quite quiet, and went and sat on the edge of his
4101𓀴bed resignedly, and looked into space with lack-lustre eyes. I thought I
4102𓀴would find out if his apathy were real or only assumed, and tried to
4103𓀴lead him to talk of his pets, a theme which had never failed to excite
4104𓀴his attention. At first he made no reply, but at length said testily:--
4105𓀴
4106𓀴“Bother them all! I don’t care a pin about them.”
4107𓀴
4108𓀴“What?” I said. “You don’t mean to tell me you don’t care about
4109𓀴spiders?” (Spiders at present are his hobby and the note-book is filling
4110𓀴up with columns of small figures.) To this he answered enigmatically:--
4111𓀴
4112𓀴“The bride-maidens rejoice the eyes that wait the coming of the bride;
4113𓀴but when the bride draweth nigh, then the maidens shine not to the eyes
4114𓀴that are filled.”
4115𓀴
4116𓀴He would not explain himself, but remained obstinately seated on his bed
4117𓀴all the time I remained with him.
4118𓀴
4119𓀴I am weary to-night and low in spirits. I cannot but think of Lucy, and
4120𓀴how different things might have been. If I don’t sleep at once, chloral,
4121𓀴the modern Morpheus--C_{2}HCl_{3}O. H_{2}O! I must be careful not to let
4122𓀴it grow into a habit. No, I shall take none to-night! I have thought of
4123𓀴Lucy, and I shall not dishonour her by mixing the two. If need be,
4124𓀴to-night shall be sleepless....
4125𓀴
4126𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
4127𓀴
4128𓀴_Later._--Glad I made the resolution; gladder that I kept to it. I had
4129𓀴lain tossing about, and had heard the clock strike only twice, when the
4130𓀴night-watchman came to me, sent up from the ward, to say that Renfield
4131𓀴had escaped. I threw on my clothes and ran down at once; my patient is
4132𓀴too dangerous a person to be roaming about. Those ideas of his might
4133𓀴work out dangerously with strangers. The attendant was waiting for me.
4134𓀴He said he had seen him not ten minutes before, seemingly asleep in his
4135𓀴bed, when he had looked through the observation-trap in the door. His
4136𓀴attention was called by the sound of the window being wrenched out. He
4137𓀴ran back and saw his feet disappear through the window, and had at once
4138𓀴sent up for me. He was only in his night-gear, and cannot be far off.
4139𓀴The attendant thought it would be more useful to watch where he should
4140𓀴go than to follow him, as he might lose sight of him whilst getting out
4141𓀴of the building by the door. He is a bulky man, and couldn’t get through
4142𓀴the window. I am thin, so, with his aid, I got out, but feet foremost,
4143𓀴and, as we were only a few feet above ground, landed unhurt. The
4144𓀴attendant told me the patient had gone to the left, and had taken a
4145𓀴straight line, so I ran as quickly as I could. As I got through the belt
4146𓀴of trees I saw a white figure scale the high wall which separates our
4147𓀴grounds from those of the deserted house.
4148𓀴
4149𓀴I ran back at once, told the watchman to get three or four men
4150𓀴immediately and follow me into the grounds of Carfax, in case our friend
4151𓀴might be dangerous. I got a ladder myself, and crossing the wall,
4152𓀴dropped down on the other side. I could see Renfield’s figure just
4153𓀴disappearing behind the angle of the house, so I ran after him. On the
4154𓀴far side of the house I found him pressed close against the old
4155𓀴ironbound oak door of the chapel. He was talking, apparently to some
4156𓀴one, but I was afraid to go near enough to hear what he was saying, lest
4157𓀴I might frighten him, and he should run off. Chasing an errant swarm of
4158𓀴bees is nothing to following a naked lunatic, when the fit of escaping
4159𓀴is upon him! After a few minutes, however, I could see that he did not
4160𓀴take note of anything around him, and so ventured to draw nearer to
4161𓀴him--the more so as my men had now crossed the wall and were closing him
4162𓀴in. I heard him say:--
4163𓀴
4164𓀴“I am here to do Your bidding, Master. I am Your slave, and You will
4165𓀴reward me, for I shall be faithful. I have worshipped You long and afar
4166𓀴off. Now that You are near, I await Your commands, and You will not pass
4167𓀴me by, will You, dear Master, in Your distribution of good things?”
4168𓀴
4169𓀴He _is_ a selfish old beggar anyhow. He thinks of the loaves and fishes
4170𓀴even when he believes he is in a Real Presence. His manias make a
4171𓀴startling combination. When we closed in on him he fought like a tiger.
4172𓀴He is immensely strong, for he was more like a wild beast than a man. I
4173𓀴never saw a lunatic in such a paroxysm of rage before; and I hope I
4174𓀴shall not again. It is a mercy that we have found out his strength and
4175𓀴his danger in good time. With strength and determination like his, he
4176𓀴might have done wild work before he was caged. He is safe now at any
4177𓀴rate. Jack Sheppard himself couldn’t get free from the strait-waistcoat
4178𓀴that keeps him restrained, and he’s chained to the wall in the padded
4179𓀴room. His cries are at times awful, but the silences that follow are
4180𓀴more deadly still, for he means murder in every turn and movement.
4181𓀴
4182𓀴Just now he spoke coherent words for the first time:--
4183𓀴
4184𓀴“I shall be patient, Master. It is coming--coming--coming!”
4185𓀴
4186𓀴So I took the hint, and came too. I was too excited to sleep, but this
4187𓀴diary has quieted me, and I feel I shall get some sleep to-night.
4188𓀴
4189𓀴
4190𓀴
4191𓀴
4192𓀴CHAPTER IX
4193𓀴
4194𓀴
4195𓀴_Letter, Mina Harker to Lucy Westenra._
4196𓀴
4197𓀴“_Buda-Pesth, 24 August._
4198𓀴
4199𓀴“My dearest Lucy,--
4200𓀴
4201𓀴“I know you will be anxious to hear all that has happened since we
4202𓀴parted at the railway station at Whitby. Well, my dear, I got to Hull
4203𓀴all right, and caught the boat to Hamburg, and then the train on here. I
4204𓀴feel that I can hardly recall anything of the journey, except that I
4205𓀴knew I was coming to Jonathan, and, that as I should have to do some
4206𓀴nursing, I had better get all the sleep I could.... I found my dear one,
4207𓀴oh, so thin and pale and weak-looking. All the resolution has gone out
4208𓀴of his dear eyes, and that quiet dignity which I told you was in his
4209𓀴face has vanished. He is only a wreck of himself, and he does not
4210𓀴remember anything that has happened to him for a long time past. At
4211𓀴least, he wants me to believe so, and I shall never ask. He has had some
4212𓀴terrible shock, and I fear it might tax his poor brain if he were to try
4213𓀴to recall it. Sister Agatha, who is a good creature and a born nurse,
4214𓀴tells me that he raved of dreadful things whilst he was off his head. I
4215𓀴wanted her to tell me what they were; but she would only cross herself,
4216𓀴and say she would never tell; that the ravings of the sick were the
4217𓀴secrets of God, and that if a nurse through her vocation should hear
4218𓀴them, she should respect her trust. She is a sweet, good soul, and the
4219𓀴next day, when she saw I was troubled, she opened up the subject again,
4220𓀴and after saying that she could never mention what my poor dear raved
4221𓀴about, added: ‘I can tell you this much, my dear: that it was not about
4222𓀴anything which he has done wrong himself; and you, as his wife to be,
4223𓀴have no cause to be concerned. He has not forgotten you or what he owes
4224𓀴to you. His fear was of great and terrible things, which no mortal can
4225𓀴treat of.’ I do believe the dear soul thought I might be jealous lest my
4226𓀴poor dear should have fallen in love with any other girl. The idea of
4227𓀴_my_ being jealous about Jonathan! And yet, my dear, let me whisper, I
4228𓀴felt a thrill of joy through me when I _knew_ that no other woman was a
4229𓀴cause of trouble. I am now sitting by his bedside, where I can see his
4230𓀴face while he sleeps. He is waking!...
4231𓀴
4232𓀴“When he woke he asked me for his coat, as he wanted to get something
4233𓀴from the pocket; I asked Sister Agatha, and she brought all his things.
4234𓀴I saw that amongst them was his note-book, and was going to ask him to
4235𓀴let me look at it--for I knew then that I might find some clue to his
4236𓀴trouble--but I suppose he must have seen my wish in my eyes, for he sent
4237𓀴me over to the window, saying he wanted to be quite alone for a moment.
4238𓀴Then he called me back, and when I came he had his hand over the
4239𓀴note-book, and he said to me very solemnly:--
4240𓀴
4241𓀴“‘Wilhelmina’--I knew then that he was in deadly earnest, for he has
4242𓀴never called me by that name since he asked me to marry him--‘you know,
4243𓀴dear, my ideas of the trust between husband and wife: there should be no
4244𓀴secret, no concealment. I have had a great shock, and when I try to
4245𓀴think of what it is I feel my head spin round, and I do not know if it
4246𓀴was all real or the dreaming of a madman. You know I have had brain
4247𓀴fever, and that is to be mad. The secret is here, and I do not want to
4248𓀴know it. I want to take up my life here, with our marriage.’ For, my
4249𓀴dear, we had decided to be married as soon as the formalities are
4250𓀴complete. ‘Are you willing, Wilhelmina, to share my ignorance? Here is
4251𓀴the book. Take it and keep it, read it if you will, but never let me
4252𓀴know; unless, indeed, some solemn duty should come upon me to go back to
4253𓀴the bitter hours, asleep or awake, sane or mad, recorded here.’ He fell
4254𓀴back exhausted, and I put the book under his pillow, and kissed him. I
4255𓀴have asked Sister Agatha to beg the Superior to let our wedding be this
4256𓀴afternoon, and am waiting her reply....
4257𓀴
4258𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
4259𓀴
4260𓀴“She has come and told me that the chaplain of the English mission
4261𓀴church has been sent for. We are to be married in an hour, or as soon
4262𓀴after as Jonathan awakes....
4263𓀴
4264𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
4265𓀴
4266𓀴“Lucy, the time has come and gone. I feel very solemn, but very, very
4267𓀴happy. Jonathan woke a little after the hour, and all was ready, and he
4268𓀴sat up in bed, propped up with pillows. He answered his ‘I will’ firmly
4269𓀴and strongly. I could hardly speak; my heart was so full that even those
4270𓀴words seemed to choke me. The dear sisters were so kind. Please God, I
4271𓀴shall never, never forget them, nor the grave and sweet responsibilities
4272𓀴I have taken upon me. I must tell you of my wedding present. When the
4273𓀴chaplain and the sisters had left me alone with my husband--oh, Lucy, it
4274𓀴is the first time I have written the words ‘my husband’--left me alone
4275𓀴with my husband, I took the book from under his pillow, and wrapped it
4276𓀴up in white paper, and tied it with a little bit of pale blue ribbon
4277𓀴which was round my neck, and sealed it over the knot with sealing-wax,
4278𓀴and for my seal I used my wedding ring. Then I kissed it and showed it
4279𓀴to my husband, and told him that I would keep it so, and then it would
4280𓀴be an outward and visible sign for us all our lives that we trusted each
4281𓀴other; that I would never open it unless it were for his own dear sake
4282𓀴or for the sake of some stern duty. Then he took my hand in his, and oh,
4283𓀴Lucy, it was the first time he took _his wife’s_ hand, and said that it
4284𓀴was the dearest thing in all the wide world, and that he would go
4285𓀴through all the past again to win it, if need be. The poor dear meant to
4286𓀴have said a part of the past, but he cannot think of time yet, and I
4287𓀴shall not wonder if at first he mixes up not only the month, but the
4288𓀴year.
4289𓀴
4290𓀴“Well, my dear, what could I say? I could only tell him that I was the
4291𓀴happiest woman in all the wide world, and that I had nothing to give him
4292𓀴except myself, my life, and my trust, and that with these went my love
4293𓀴and duty for all the days of my life. And, my dear, when he kissed me,
4294𓀴and drew me to him with his poor weak hands, it was like a very solemn
4295𓀴pledge between us....
4296𓀴
4297𓀴“Lucy dear, do you know why I tell you all this? It is not only because
4298𓀴it is all sweet to me, but because you have been, and are, very dear to
4299𓀴me. It was my privilege to be your friend and guide when you came from
4300𓀴the schoolroom to prepare for the world of life. I want you to see now,
4301𓀴and with the eyes of a very happy wife, whither duty has led me; so that
4302𓀴in your own married life you too may be all happy as I am. My dear,
4303𓀴please Almighty God, your life may be all it promises: a long day of
4304𓀴sunshine, with no harsh wind, no forgetting duty, no distrust. I must
4305𓀴not wish you no pain, for that can never be; but I do hope you will be
4306𓀴_always_ as happy as I am _now_. Good-bye, my dear. I shall post this at
4307𓀴once, and, perhaps, write you very soon again. I must stop, for Jonathan
4308𓀴is waking--I must attend to my husband!
4309𓀴
4310𓀴“Your ever-loving
4311𓀴
4312𓀴“MINA HARKER.”
4313𓀴
4314𓀴
4315𓀴_Letter, Lucy Westenra to Mina Harker._
4316𓀴
4317𓀴“_Whitby, 30 August._
4318𓀴
4319𓀴“My dearest Mina,--
4320𓀴
4321𓀴“Oceans of love and millions of kisses, and may you soon be in your own
4322𓀴home with your husband. I wish you could be coming home soon enough to
4323𓀴stay with us here. The strong air would soon restore Jonathan; it has
4324𓀴quite restored me. I have an appetite like a cormorant, am full of
4325𓀴life, and sleep well. You will be glad to know that I have quite given
4326𓀴up walking in my sleep. I think I have not stirred out of my bed for a
4327𓀴week, that is when I once got into it at night. Arthur says I am getting
4328𓀴fat. By the way, I forgot to tell you that Arthur is here. We have such
4329𓀴walks and drives, and rides, and rowing, and tennis, and fishing
4330𓀴together; and I love him more than ever. He _tells_ me that he loves me
4331𓀴more, but I doubt that, for at first he told me that he couldn’t love me
4332𓀴more than he did then. But this is nonsense. There he is, calling to me.
4333𓀴So no more just at present from your loving
4334𓀴
4335𓀴“LUCY.
4336𓀴
4337𓀴“P. S.--Mother sends her love. She seems better, poor dear.
4338𓀴“P. P. S.--We are to be married on 28 September.”
4339𓀴
4340𓀴
4341𓀴_Dr. Seward’s Diary._
4342𓀴
4343𓀴_20 August._--The case of Renfield grows even more interesting. He has
4344𓀴now so far quieted that there are spells of cessation from his passion.
4345𓀴For the first week after his attack he was perpetually violent. Then one
4346𓀴night, just as the moon rose, he grew quiet, and kept murmuring to
4347𓀴himself: “Now I can wait; now I can wait.” The attendant came to tell
4348𓀴me, so I ran down at once to have a look at him. He was still in the
4349𓀴strait-waistcoat and in the padded room, but the suffused look had gone
4350𓀴from his face, and his eyes had something of their old pleading--I might
4351𓀴almost say, “cringing”--softness. I was satisfied with his present
4352𓀴condition, and directed him to be relieved. The attendants hesitated,
4353𓀴but finally carried out my wishes without protest. It was a strange
4354𓀴thing that the patient had humour enough to see their distrust, for,
4355𓀴coming close to me, he said in a whisper, all the while looking
4356𓀴furtively at them:--
4357𓀴
4358𓀴“They think I could hurt you! Fancy _me_ hurting _you_! The fools!”
4359𓀴
4360𓀴It was soothing, somehow, to the feelings to find myself dissociated
4361𓀴even in the mind of this poor madman from the others; but all the same I
4362𓀴do not follow his thought. Am I to take it that I have anything in
4363𓀴common with him, so that we are, as it were, to stand together; or has
4364𓀴he to gain from me some good so stupendous that my well-being is needful
4365𓀴to him? I must find out later on. To-night he will not speak. Even the
4366𓀴offer of a kitten or even a full-grown cat will not tempt him. He will
4367𓀴only say: “I don’t take any stock in cats. I have more to think of now,
4368𓀴and I can wait; I can wait.”
4369𓀴
4370𓀴After a while I left him. The attendant tells me that he was quiet
4371𓀴until just before dawn, and that then he began to get uneasy, and at
4372𓀴length violent, until at last he fell into a paroxysm which exhausted
4373𓀴him so that he swooned into a sort of coma.
4374𓀴
4375𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
4376𓀴
4377𓀴... Three nights has the same thing happened--violent all day then quiet
4378𓀴from moonrise to sunrise. I wish I could get some clue to the cause. It
4379𓀴would almost seem as if there was some influence which came and went.
4380𓀴Happy thought! We shall to-night play sane wits against mad ones. He
4381𓀴escaped before without our help; to-night he shall escape with it. We
4382𓀴shall give him a chance, and have the men ready to follow in case they
4383𓀴are required....
4384𓀴
4385𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
4386𓀴
4387𓀴_23 August._--“The unexpected always happens.” How well Disraeli knew
4388𓀴life. Our bird when he found the cage open would not fly, so all our
4389𓀴subtle arrangements were for nought. At any rate, we have proved one
4390𓀴thing; that the spells of quietness last a reasonable time. We shall in
4391𓀴future be able to ease his bonds for a few hours each day. I have given
4392𓀴orders to the night attendant merely to shut him in the padded room,
4393𓀴when once he is quiet, until an hour before sunrise. The poor soul’s
4394𓀴body will enjoy the relief even if his mind cannot appreciate it. Hark!
4395𓀴The unexpected again! I am called; the patient has once more escaped.
4396𓀴
4397𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
4398𓀴
4399𓀴_Later._--Another night adventure. Renfield artfully waited until the
4400𓀴attendant was entering the room to inspect. Then he dashed out past him
4401𓀴and flew down the passage. I sent word for the attendants to follow.
4402𓀴Again he went into the grounds of the deserted house, and we found him
4403𓀴in the same place, pressed against the old chapel door. When he saw me
4404𓀴he became furious, and had not the attendants seized him in time, he
4405𓀴would have tried to kill me. As we were holding him a strange thing
4406𓀴happened. He suddenly redoubled his efforts, and then as suddenly grew
4407𓀴calm. I looked round instinctively, but could see nothing. Then I caught
4408𓀴the patient’s eye and followed it, but could trace nothing as it looked
4409𓀴into the moonlit sky except a big bat, which was flapping its silent and
4410𓀴ghostly way to the west. Bats usually wheel and flit about, but this one
4411𓀴seemed to go straight on, as if it knew where it was bound for or had
4412𓀴some intention of its own. The patient grew calmer every instant, and
4413𓀴presently said:--
4414𓀴
4415𓀴“You needn’t tie me; I shall go quietly!” Without trouble we came back
4416𓀴to the house. I feel there is something ominous in his calm, and shall
4417𓀴not forget this night....
4418𓀴
4419𓀴
4420𓀴_Lucy Westenra’s Diary_
4421𓀴
4422𓀴_Hillingham, 24 August._--I must imitate Mina, and keep writing things
4423𓀴down. Then we can have long talks when we do meet. I wonder when it will
4424𓀴be. I wish she were with me again, for I feel so unhappy. Last night I
4425𓀴seemed to be dreaming again just as I was at Whitby. Perhaps it is the
4426𓀴change of air, or getting home again. It is all dark and horrid to me,
4427𓀴for I can remember nothing; but I am full of vague fear, and I feel so
4428𓀴weak and worn out. When Arthur came to lunch he looked quite grieved
4429𓀴when he saw me, and I hadn’t the spirit to try to be cheerful. I wonder
4430𓀴if I could sleep in mother’s room to-night. I shall make an excuse and
4431𓀴try.
4432𓀴
4433𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
4434𓀴
4435𓀴_25 August._--Another bad night. Mother did not seem to take to my
4436𓀴proposal. She seems not too well herself, and doubtless she fears to
4437𓀴worry me. I tried to keep awake, and succeeded for a while; but when the
4438𓀴clock struck twelve it waked me from a doze, so I must have been falling
4439𓀴asleep. There was a sort of scratching or flapping at the window, but I
4440𓀴did not mind it, and as I remember no more, I suppose I must then have
4441𓀴fallen asleep. More bad dreams. I wish I could remember them. This
4442𓀴morning I am horribly weak. My face is ghastly pale, and my throat pains
4443𓀴me. It must be something wrong with my lungs, for I don’t seem ever to
4444𓀴get air enough. I shall try to cheer up when Arthur comes, or else I
4445𓀴know he will be miserable to see me so.
4446𓀴
4447𓀴
4448𓀴_Letter, Arthur Holmwood to Dr. Seward._
4449𓀴
4450𓀴“_Albemarle Hotel, 31 August._
4451𓀴
4452𓀴“My dear Jack,--
4453𓀴
4454𓀴“I want you to do me a favour. Lucy is ill; that is, she has no special
4455𓀴disease, but she looks awful, and is getting worse every day. I have
4456𓀴asked her if there is any cause; I do not dare to ask her mother, for to
4457𓀴disturb the poor lady’s mind about her daughter in her present state of
4458𓀴health would be fatal. Mrs. Westenra has confided to me that her doom is
4459𓀴spoken--disease of the heart--though poor Lucy does not know it yet. I
4460𓀴am sure that there is something preying on my dear girl’s mind. I am
4461𓀴almost distracted when I think of her; to look at her gives me a pang. I
4462𓀴told her I should ask you to see her, and though she demurred at
4463𓀴first--I know why, old fellow--she finally consented. It will be a
4464𓀴painful task for you, I know, old friend, but it is for _her_ sake, and
4465𓀴I must not hesitate to ask, or you to act. You are to come to lunch at
4466𓀴Hillingham to-morrow, two o’clock, so as not to arouse any suspicion in
4467𓀴Mrs. Westenra, and after lunch Lucy will take an opportunity of being
4468𓀴alone with you. I shall come in for tea, and we can go away together; I
4469𓀴am filled with anxiety, and want to consult with you alone as soon as I
4470𓀴can after you have seen her. Do not fail!
4471𓀴
4472𓀴“ARTHUR.”
4473𓀴
4474𓀴
4475𓀴_Telegram, Arthur Holmwood to Seward._
4476𓀴
4477𓀴“_1 September._
4478𓀴
4479𓀴“Am summoned to see my father, who is worse. Am writing. Write me fully
4480𓀴by to-night’s post to Ring. Wire me if necessary.”
4481𓀴
4482𓀴
4483𓀴_Letter from Dr. Seward to Arthur Holmwood._
4484𓀴
4485𓀴“_2 September._
4486𓀴
4487𓀴“My dear old fellow,--
4488𓀴
4489𓀴“With regard to Miss Westenra’s health I hasten to let you know at once
4490𓀴that in my opinion there is not any functional disturbance or any malady
4491𓀴that I know of. At the same time, I am not by any means satisfied with
4492𓀴her appearance; she is woefully different from what she was when I saw
4493𓀴her last. Of course you must bear in mind that I did not have full
4494𓀴opportunity of examination such as I should wish; our very friendship
4495𓀴makes a little difficulty which not even medical science or custom can
4496𓀴bridge over. I had better tell you exactly what happened, leaving you to
4497𓀴draw, in a measure, your own conclusions. I shall then say what I have
4498𓀴done and propose doing.
4499𓀴
4500𓀴“I found Miss Westenra in seemingly gay spirits. Her mother was present,
4501𓀴and in a few seconds I made up my mind that she was trying all she knew
4502𓀴to mislead her mother and prevent her from being anxious. I have no
4503𓀴doubt she guesses, if she does not know, what need of caution there is.
4504𓀴We lunched alone, and as we all exerted ourselves to be cheerful, we
4505𓀴got, as some kind of reward for our labours, some real cheerfulness
4506𓀴amongst us. Then Mrs. Westenra went to lie down, and Lucy was left with
4507𓀴me. We went into her boudoir, and till we got there her gaiety remained,
4508𓀴for the servants were coming and going. As soon as the door was closed,
4509𓀴however, the mask fell from her face, and she sank down into a chair
4510𓀴with a great sigh, and hid her eyes with her hand. When I saw that her
4511𓀴high spirits had failed, I at once took advantage of her reaction to
4512𓀴make a diagnosis. She said to me very sweetly:--
4513𓀴
4514𓀴“‘I cannot tell you how I loathe talking about myself.’ I reminded her
4515𓀴that a doctor’s confidence was sacred, but that you were grievously
4516𓀴anxious about her. She caught on to my meaning at once, and settled that
4517𓀴matter in a word. ‘Tell Arthur everything you choose. I do not care for
4518𓀴myself, but all for him!’ So I am quite free.
4519𓀴
4520𓀴“I could easily see that she is somewhat bloodless, but I could not see
4521𓀴the usual anæmic signs, and by a chance I was actually able to test the
4522𓀴quality of her blood, for in opening a window which was stiff a cord
4523𓀴gave way, and she cut her hand slightly with broken glass. It was a
4524𓀴slight matter in itself, but it gave me an evident chance, and I secured
4525𓀴a few drops of the blood and have analysed them. The qualitative
4526𓀴analysis gives a quite normal condition, and shows, I should infer, in
4527𓀴itself a vigorous state of health. In other physical matters I was quite
4528𓀴satisfied that there is no need for anxiety; but as there must be a
4529𓀴cause somewhere, I have come to the conclusion that it must be something
4530𓀴mental. She complains of difficulty in breathing satisfactorily at
4531𓀴times, and of heavy, lethargic sleep, with dreams that frighten her, but
4532𓀴regarding which she can remember nothing. She says that as a child she
4533𓀴used to walk in her sleep, and that when in Whitby the habit came back,
4534𓀴and that once she walked out in the night and went to East Cliff, where
4535𓀴Miss Murray found her; but she assures me that of late the habit has not
4536𓀴returned. I am in doubt, and so have done the best thing I know of; I
4537𓀴have written to my old friend and master, Professor Van Helsing, of
4538𓀴Amsterdam, who knows as much about obscure diseases as any one in the
4539𓀴world. I have asked him to come over, and as you told me that all things
4540𓀴were to be at your charge, I have mentioned to him who you are and your
4541𓀴relations to Miss Westenra. This, my dear fellow, is in obedience to
4542𓀴your wishes, for I am only too proud and happy to do anything I can for
4543𓀴her. Van Helsing would, I know, do anything for me for a personal
4544𓀴reason, so, no matter on what ground he comes, we must accept his
4545𓀴wishes. He is a seemingly arbitrary man, but this is because he knows
4546𓀴what he is talking about better than any one else. He is a philosopher
4547𓀴and a metaphysician, and one of the most advanced scientists of his day;
4548𓀴and he has, I believe, an absolutely open mind. This, with an iron
4549𓀴nerve, a temper of the ice-brook, an indomitable resolution,
4550𓀴self-command, and toleration exalted from virtues to blessings, and the
4551𓀴kindliest and truest heart that beats--these form his equipment for the
4552𓀴noble work that he is doing for mankind--work both in theory and
4553𓀴practice, for his views are as wide as his all-embracing sympathy. I
4554𓀴tell you these facts that you may know why I have such confidence in
4555𓀴him. I have asked him to come at once. I shall see Miss Westenra
4556𓀴to-morrow again. She is to meet me at the Stores, so that I may not
4557𓀴alarm her mother by too early a repetition of my call.
4558𓀴
4559𓀴“Yours always,
4560𓀴
4561𓀴“JOHN SEWARD.”
4562𓀴
4563𓀴
4564𓀴_Letter, Abraham Van Helsing, M. D., D. Ph., D. Lit., etc., etc., to Dr.
4565𓀴Seward._
4566𓀴
4567𓀴“_2 September._
4568𓀴
4569𓀴“My good Friend,--
4570𓀴
4571𓀴“When I have received your letter I am already coming to you. By good
4572𓀴fortune I can leave just at once, without wrong to any of those who have
4573𓀴trusted me. Were fortune other, then it were bad for those who have
4574𓀴trusted, for I come to my friend when he call me to aid those he holds
4575𓀴dear. Tell your friend that when that time you suck from my wound so
4576𓀴swiftly the poison of the gangrene from that knife that our other
4577𓀴friend, too nervous, let slip, you did more for him when he wants my
4578𓀴aids and you call for them than all his great fortune could do. But it
4579𓀴is pleasure added to do for him, your friend; it is to you that I come.
4580𓀴Have then rooms for me at the Great Eastern Hotel, so that I may be near
4581𓀴to hand, and please it so arrange that we may see the young lady not too
4582𓀴late on to-morrow, for it is likely that I may have to return here that
4583𓀴night. But if need be I shall come again in three days, and stay longer
4584𓀴if it must. Till then good-bye, my friend John.
4585𓀴
4586𓀴 “VAN HELSING.”
4587𓀴
4588𓀴
4589𓀴_Letter, Dr. Seward to Hon. Arthur Holmwood._
4590𓀴
4591𓀴“_3 September._
4592𓀴
4593𓀴“My dear Art,--
4594𓀴
4595𓀴“Van Helsing has come and gone. He came on with me to Hillingham, and
4596𓀴found that, by Lucy’s discretion, her mother was lunching out, so that
4597𓀴we were alone with her. Van Helsing made a very careful examination of
4598𓀴the patient. He is to report to me, and I shall advise you, for of
4599𓀴course I was not present all the time. He is, I fear, much concerned,
4600𓀴but says he must think. When I told him of our friendship and how you
4601𓀴trust to me in the matter, he said: ‘You must tell him all you think.
4602𓀴Tell him what I think, if you can guess it, if you will. Nay, I am not
4603𓀴jesting. This is no jest, but life and death, perhaps more.’ I asked
4604𓀴what he meant by that, for he was very serious. This was when we had
4605𓀴come back to town, and he was having a cup of tea before starting on his
4606𓀴return to Amsterdam. He would not give me any further clue. You must not
4607𓀴be angry with me, Art, because his very reticence means that all his
4608𓀴brains are working for her good. He will speak plainly enough when the
4609𓀴time comes, be sure. So I told him I would simply write an account of
4610𓀴our visit, just as if I were doing a descriptive special article for
4611𓀴_The Daily Telegraph_. He seemed not to notice, but remarked that the
4612𓀴smuts in London were not quite so bad as they used to be when he was a
4613𓀴student here. I am to get his report to-morrow if he can possibly make
4614𓀴it. In any case I am to have a letter.
4615𓀴
4616𓀴“Well, as to the visit. Lucy was more cheerful than on the day I first
4617𓀴saw her, and certainly looked better. She had lost something of the
4618𓀴ghastly look that so upset you, and her breathing was normal. She was
4619𓀴very sweet to the professor (as she always is), and tried to make him
4620𓀴feel at ease; though I could see that the poor girl was making a hard
4621𓀴struggle for it. I believe Van Helsing saw it, too, for I saw the quick
4622𓀴look under his bushy brows that I knew of old. Then he began to chat of
4623𓀴all things except ourselves and diseases and with such an infinite
4624𓀴geniality that I could see poor Lucy’s pretense of animation merge into
4625𓀴reality. Then, without any seeming change, he brought the conversation
4626𓀴gently round to his visit, and suavely said:--
4627𓀴
4628𓀴“‘My dear young miss, I have the so great pleasure because you are so
4629𓀴much beloved. That is much, my dear, ever were there that which I do not
4630𓀴see. They told me you were down in the spirit, and that you were of a
4631𓀴ghastly pale. To them I say: “Pouf!”’ And he snapped his fingers at me
4632𓀴and went on: ‘But you and I shall show them how wrong they are. How can
4633𓀴he’--and he pointed at me with the same look and gesture as that with
4634𓀴which once he pointed me out to his class, on, or rather after, a
4635𓀴particular occasion which he never fails to remind me of--‘know anything
4636𓀴of a young ladies? He has his madmans to play with, and to bring them
4637𓀴back to happiness, and to those that love them. It is much to do, and,
4638𓀴oh, but there are rewards, in that we can bestow such happiness. But the
4639𓀴young ladies! He has no wife nor daughter, and the young do not tell
4640𓀴themselves to the young, but to the old, like me, who have known so many
4641𓀴sorrows and the causes of them. So, my dear, we will send him away to
4642𓀴smoke the cigarette in the garden, whiles you and I have little talk all
4643𓀴to ourselves.’ I took the hint, and strolled about, and presently the
4644𓀴professor came to the window and called me in. He looked grave, but
4645𓀴said: ‘I have made careful examination, but there is no functional
4646𓀴cause. With you I agree that there has been much blood lost; it has
4647𓀴been, but is not. But the conditions of her are in no way anæmic. I have
4648𓀴asked her to send me her maid, that I may ask just one or two question,
4649𓀴that so I may not chance to miss nothing. I know well what she will say.
4650𓀴And yet there is cause; there is always cause for everything. I must go
4651𓀴back home and think. You must send to me the telegram every day; and if
4652𓀴there be cause I shall come again. The disease--for not to be all well
4653𓀴is a disease--interest me, and the sweet young dear, she interest me
4654𓀴too. She charm me, and for her, if not for you or disease, I come.’
4655𓀴
4656𓀴“As I tell you, he would not say a word more, even when we were alone.
4657𓀴And so now, Art, you know all I know. I shall keep stern watch. I trust
4658𓀴your poor father is rallying. It must be a terrible thing to you, my
4659𓀴dear old fellow, to be placed in such a position between two people who
4660𓀴are both so dear to you. I know your idea of duty to your father, and
4661𓀴you are right to stick to it; but, if need be, I shall send you word to
4662𓀴come at once to Lucy; so do not be over-anxious unless you hear from
4663𓀴me.”
4664𓀴
4665𓀴
4666𓀴_Dr. Seward’s Diary._
4667𓀴
4668𓀴_4 September._--Zoöphagous patient still keeps up our interest in him.
4669𓀴He had only one outburst and that was yesterday at an unusual time. Just
4670𓀴before the stroke of noon he began to grow restless. The attendant knew
4671𓀴the symptoms, and at once summoned aid. Fortunately the men came at a
4672𓀴run, and were just in time, for at the stroke of noon he became so
4673𓀴violent that it took all their strength to hold him. In about five
4674𓀴minutes, however, he began to get more and more quiet, and finally sank
4675𓀴into a sort of melancholy, in which state he has remained up to now. The
4676𓀴attendant tells me that his screams whilst in the paroxysm were really
4677𓀴appalling; I found my hands full when I got in, attending to some of the
4678𓀴other patients who were frightened by him. Indeed, I can quite
4679𓀴understand the effect, for the sounds disturbed even me, though I was
4680𓀴some distance away. It is now after the dinner-hour of the asylum, and
4681𓀴as yet my patient sits in a corner brooding, with a dull, sullen,
4682𓀴woe-begone look in his face, which seems rather to indicate than to show
4683𓀴something directly. I cannot quite understand it.
4684𓀴
4685𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
4686𓀴
4687𓀴_Later._--Another change in my patient. At five o’clock I looked in on
4688𓀴him, and found him seemingly as happy and contented as he used to be. He
4689𓀴was catching flies and eating them, and was keeping note of his capture
4690𓀴by making nail-marks on the edge of the door between the ridges of
4691𓀴padding. When he saw me, he came over and apologised for his bad
4692𓀴conduct, and asked me in a very humble, cringing way to be led back to
4693𓀴his own room and to have his note-book again. I thought it well to
4694𓀴humour him: so he is back in his room with the window open. He has the
4695𓀴sugar of his tea spread out on the window-sill, and is reaping quite a
4696𓀴harvest of flies. He is not now eating them, but putting them into a
4697𓀴box, as of old, and is already examining the corners of his room to find
4698𓀴a spider. I tried to get him to talk about the past few days, for any
4699𓀴clue to his thoughts would be of immense help to me; but he would not
4700𓀴rise. For a moment or two he looked very sad, and said in a sort of
4701𓀴far-away voice, as though saying it rather to himself than to me:--
4702𓀴
4703𓀴“All over! all over! He has deserted me. No hope for me now unless I do
4704𓀴it for myself!” Then suddenly turning to me in a resolute way, he said:
4705𓀴“Doctor, won’t you be very good to me and let me have a little more
4706𓀴sugar? I think it would be good for me.”
4707𓀴
4708𓀴“And the flies?” I said.
4709𓀴
4710𓀴“Yes! The flies like it, too, and I like the flies; therefore I like
4711𓀴it.” And there are people who know so little as to think that madmen do
4712𓀴not argue. I procured him a double supply, and left him as happy a man
4713𓀴as, I suppose, any in the world. I wish I could fathom his mind.
4714𓀴
4715𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
4716𓀴
4717𓀴_Midnight._--Another change in him. I had been to see Miss Westenra,
4718𓀴whom I found much better, and had just returned, and was standing at our
4719𓀴own gate looking at the sunset, when once more I heard him yelling. As
4720𓀴his room is on this side of the house, I could hear it better than in
4721𓀴the morning. It was a shock to me to turn from the wonderful smoky
4722𓀴beauty of a sunset over London, with its lurid lights and inky shadows
4723𓀴and all the marvellous tints that come on foul clouds even as on foul
4724𓀴water, and to realise all the grim sternness of my own cold stone
4725𓀴building, with its wealth of breathing misery, and my own desolate heart
4726𓀴to endure it all. I reached him just as the sun was going down, and from
4727𓀴his window saw the red disc sink. As it sank he became less and less
4728𓀴frenzied; and just as it dipped he slid from the hands that held him, an
4729𓀴inert mass, on the floor. It is wonderful, however, what intellectual
4730𓀴recuperative power lunatics have, for within a few minutes he stood up
4731𓀴quite calmly and looked around him. I signalled to the attendants not to
4732𓀴hold him, for I was anxious to see what he would do. He went straight
4733𓀴over to the window and brushed out the crumbs of sugar; then he took his
4734𓀴fly-box, and emptied it outside, and threw away the box; then he shut
4735𓀴the window, and crossing over, sat down on his bed. All this surprised
4736𓀴me, so I asked him: “Are you not going to keep flies any more?”
4737𓀴
4738𓀴“No,” said he; “I am sick of all that rubbish!” He certainly is a
4739𓀴wonderfully interesting study. I wish I could get some glimpse of his
4740𓀴mind or of the cause of his sudden passion. Stop; there may be a clue
4741𓀴after all, if we can find why to-day his paroxysms came on at high noon
4742𓀴and at sunset. Can it be that there is a malign influence of the sun at
4743𓀴periods which affects certain natures--as at times the moon does others?
4744𓀴We shall see.
4745𓀴
4746𓀴
4747𓀴_Telegram, Seward, London, to Van Helsing, Amsterdam._
4748𓀴
4749𓀴“_4 September._--Patient still better to-day.”
4750𓀴
4751𓀴
4752𓀴_Telegram, Seward, London, to Van Helsing, Amsterdam._
4753𓀴
4754𓀴“_5 September._--Patient greatly improved. Good appetite; sleeps
4755𓀴naturally; good spirits; colour coming back.”
4756𓀴
4757𓀴
4758𓀴_Telegram, Seward, London, to Van Helsing, Amsterdam._
4759𓀴
4760𓀴“_6 September._--Terrible change for the worse. Come at once; do not
4761𓀴lose an hour. I hold over telegram to Holmwood till have seen you.”
4762𓀴
4763𓀴
4764𓀴
4765𓀴
4766𓀴CHAPTER X
4767𓀴
4768𓀴
4769𓀴_Letter, Dr. Seward to Hon. Arthur Holmwood._
4770𓀴
4771𓀴“_6 September._
4772𓀴
4773𓀴“My dear Art,--
4774𓀴
4775𓀴“My news to-day is not so good. Lucy this morning had gone back a bit.
4776𓀴There is, however, one good thing which has arisen from it; Mrs.
4777𓀴Westenra was naturally anxious concerning Lucy, and has consulted me
4778𓀴professionally about her. I took advantage of the opportunity, and told
4779𓀴her that my old master, Van Helsing, the great specialist, was coming to
4780𓀴stay with me, and that I would put her in his charge conjointly with
4781𓀴myself; so now we can come and go without alarming her unduly, for a
4782𓀴shock to her would mean sudden death, and this, in Lucy’s weak
4783𓀴condition, might be disastrous to her. We are hedged in with
4784𓀴difficulties, all of us, my poor old fellow; but, please God, we shall
4785𓀴come through them all right. If any need I shall write, so that, if you
4786𓀴do not hear from me, take it for granted that I am simply waiting for
4787𓀴news. In haste
4788𓀴
4789𓀴“Yours ever,
4790𓀴
4791𓀴“JOHN SEWARD.”
4792𓀴
4793𓀴
4794𓀴_Dr. Seward’s Diary._
4795𓀴
4796𓀴_7 September._--The first thing Van Helsing said to me when we met at
4797𓀴Liverpool Street was:--
4798𓀴
4799𓀴“Have you said anything to our young friend the lover of her?”
4800𓀴
4801𓀴“No,” I said. “I waited till I had seen you, as I said in my telegram. I
4802𓀴wrote him a letter simply telling him that you were coming, as Miss
4803𓀴Westenra was not so well, and that I should let him know if need be.”
4804𓀴
4805𓀴“Right, my friend,” he said, “quite right! Better he not know as yet;
4806𓀴perhaps he shall never know. I pray so; but if it be needed, then he
4807𓀴shall know all. And, my good friend John, let me caution you. You deal
4808𓀴with the madmen. All men are mad in some way or the other; and inasmuch
4809𓀴as you deal discreetly with your madmen, so deal with God’s madmen,
4810𓀴too--the rest of the world. You tell not your madmen what you do nor why
4811𓀴you do it; you tell them not what you think. So you shall keep knowledge
4812𓀴in its place, where it may rest--where it may gather its kind around it
4813𓀴and breed. You and I shall keep as yet what we know here, and here.” He
4814𓀴touched me on the heart and on the forehead, and then touched himself
4815𓀴the same way. “I have for myself thoughts at the present. Later I shall
4816𓀴unfold to you.”
4817𓀴
4818𓀴“Why not now?” I asked. “It may do some good; we may arrive at some
4819𓀴decision.” He stopped and looked at me, and said:--
4820𓀴
4821𓀴“My friend John, when the corn is grown, even before it has
4822𓀴ripened--while the milk of its mother-earth is in him, and the sunshine
4823𓀴has not yet begun to paint him with his gold, the husbandman he pull the
4824𓀴ear and rub him between his rough hands, and blow away the green chaff,
4825𓀴and say to you: ‘Look! he’s good corn; he will make good crop when the
4826𓀴time comes.’” I did not see the application, and told him so. For reply
4827𓀴he reached over and took my ear in his hand and pulled it playfully, as
4828𓀴he used long ago to do at lectures, and said: “The good husbandman tell
4829𓀴you so then because he knows, but not till then. But you do not find the
4830𓀴good husbandman dig up his planted corn to see if he grow; that is for
4831𓀴the children who play at husbandry, and not for those who take it as of
4832𓀴the work of their life. See you now, friend John? I have sown my corn,
4833𓀴and Nature has her work to do in making it sprout; if he sprout at all,
4834𓀴there’s some promise; and I wait till the ear begins to swell.” He broke
4835𓀴off, for he evidently saw that I understood. Then he went on, and very
4836𓀴gravely:--
4837𓀴
4838𓀴“You were always a careful student, and your case-book was ever more
4839𓀴full than the rest. You were only student then; now you are master, and
4840𓀴I trust that good habit have not fail. Remember, my friend, that
4841𓀴knowledge is stronger than memory, and we should not trust the weaker.
4842𓀴Even if you have not kept the good practise, let me tell you that this
4843𓀴case of our dear miss is one that may be--mind, I say _may be_--of such
4844𓀴interest to us and others that all the rest may not make him kick the
4845𓀴beam, as your peoples say. Take then good note of it. Nothing is too
4846𓀴small. I counsel you, put down in record even your doubts and surmises.
4847𓀴Hereafter it may be of interest to you to see how true you guess. We
4848𓀴learn from failure, not from success!”
4849𓀴
4850𓀴When I described Lucy’s symptoms--the same as before, but infinitely
4851𓀴more marked--he looked very grave, but said nothing. He took with him a
4852𓀴bag in which were many instruments and drugs, “the ghastly paraphernalia
4853𓀴of our beneficial trade,” as he once called, in one of his lectures, the
4854𓀴equipment of a professor of the healing craft. When we were shown in,
4855𓀴Mrs. Westenra met us. She was alarmed, but not nearly so much as I
4856𓀴expected to find her. Nature in one of her beneficent moods has ordained
4857𓀴that even death has some antidote to its own terrors. Here, in a case
4858𓀴where any shock may prove fatal, matters are so ordered that, from some
4859𓀴cause or other, the things not personal--even the terrible change in her
4860𓀴daughter to whom she is so attached--do not seem to reach her. It is
4861𓀴something like the way Dame Nature gathers round a foreign body an
4862𓀴envelope of some insensitive tissue which can protect from evil that
4863𓀴which it would otherwise harm by contact. If this be an ordered
4864𓀴selfishness, then we should pause before we condemn any one for the vice
4865𓀴of egoism, for there may be deeper root for its causes than we have
4866𓀴knowledge of.
4867𓀴
4868𓀴I used my knowledge of this phase of spiritual pathology, and laid down
4869𓀴a rule that she should not be present with Lucy or think of her illness
4870𓀴more than was absolutely required. She assented readily, so readily that
4871𓀴I saw again the hand of Nature fighting for life. Van Helsing and I were
4872𓀴shown up to Lucy’s room. If I was shocked when I saw her yesterday, I
4873𓀴was horrified when I saw her to-day. She was ghastly, chalkily pale; the
4874𓀴red seemed to have gone even from her lips and gums, and the bones of
4875𓀴her face stood out prominently; her breathing was painful to see or
4876𓀴hear. Van Helsing’s face grew set as marble, and his eyebrows converged
4877𓀴till they almost touched over his nose. Lucy lay motionless, and did not
4878𓀴seem to have strength to speak, so for a while we were all silent. Then
4879𓀴Van Helsing beckoned to me, and we went gently out of the room. The
4880𓀴instant we had closed the door he stepped quickly along the passage to
4881𓀴the next door, which was open. Then he pulled me quickly in with him and
4882𓀴closed the door. “My God!” he said; “this is dreadful. There is no time
4883𓀴to be lost. She will die for sheer want of blood to keep the heart’s
4884𓀴action as it should be. There must be transfusion of blood at once. Is
4885𓀴it you or me?”
4886𓀴
4887𓀴“I am younger and stronger, Professor. It must be me.”
4888𓀴
4889𓀴“Then get ready at once. I will bring up my bag. I am prepared.”
4890𓀴
4891𓀴I went downstairs with him, and as we were going there was a knock at
4892𓀴the hall-door. When we reached the hall the maid had just opened the
4893𓀴door, and Arthur was stepping quickly in. He rushed up to me, saying in
4894𓀴an eager whisper:--
4895𓀴
4896𓀴“Jack, I was so anxious. I read between the lines of your letter, and
4897𓀴have been in an agony. The dad was better, so I ran down here to see for
4898𓀴myself. Is not that gentleman Dr. Van Helsing? I am so thankful to you,
4899𓀴sir, for coming.” When first the Professor’s eye had lit upon him he had
4900𓀴been angry at his interruption at such a time; but now, as he took in
4901𓀴his stalwart proportions and recognised the strong young manhood which
4902𓀴seemed to emanate from him, his eyes gleamed. Without a pause he said to
4903𓀴him gravely as he held out his hand:--
4904𓀴
4905𓀴“Sir, you have come in time. You are the lover of our dear miss. She is
4906𓀴bad, very, very bad. Nay, my child, do not go like that.” For he
4907𓀴suddenly grew pale and sat down in a chair almost fainting. “You are to
4908𓀴help her. You can do more than any that live, and your courage is your
4909𓀴best help.”
4910𓀴
4911𓀴“What can I do?” asked Arthur hoarsely. “Tell me, and I shall do it. My
4912𓀴life is hers, and I would give the last drop of blood in my body for
4913𓀴her.” The Professor has a strongly humorous side, and I could from old
4914𓀴knowledge detect a trace of its origin in his answer:--
4915𓀴
4916𓀴“My young sir, I do not ask so much as that--not the last!”
4917𓀴
4918𓀴“What shall I do?” There was fire in his eyes, and his open nostril
4919𓀴quivered with intent. Van Helsing slapped him on the shoulder. “Come!”
4920𓀴he said. “You are a man, and it is a man we want. You are better than
4921𓀴me, better than my friend John.” Arthur looked bewildered, and the
4922𓀴Professor went on by explaining in a kindly way:--
4923𓀴
4924𓀴“Young miss is bad, very bad. She wants blood, and blood she must have
4925𓀴or die. My friend John and I have consulted; and we are about to perform
4926𓀴what we call transfusion of blood--to transfer from full veins of one to
4927𓀴the empty veins which pine for him. John was to give his blood, as he is
4928𓀴the more young and strong than me”--here Arthur took my hand and wrung
4929𓀴it hard in silence--“but, now you are here, you are more good than us,
4930𓀴old or young, who toil much in the world of thought. Our nerves are not
4931𓀴so calm and our blood not so bright than yours!” Arthur turned to him
4932𓀴and said:--
4933𓀴
4934𓀴“If you only knew how gladly I would die for her you would
4935𓀴understand----”
4936𓀴
4937𓀴He stopped, with a sort of choke in his voice.
4938𓀴
4939𓀴“Good boy!” said Van Helsing. “In the not-so-far-off you will be happy
4940𓀴that you have done all for her you love. Come now and be silent. You
4941𓀴shall kiss her once before it is done, but then you must go; and you
4942𓀴must leave at my sign. Say no word to Madame; you know how it is with
4943𓀴her! There must be no shock; any knowledge of this would be one. Come!”
4944𓀴
4945𓀴We all went up to Lucy’s room. Arthur by direction remained outside.
4946𓀴Lucy turned her head and looked at us, but said nothing. She was not
4947𓀴asleep, but she was simply too weak to make the effort. Her eyes spoke
4948𓀴to us; that was all. Van Helsing took some things from his bag and laid
4949𓀴them on a little table out of sight. Then he mixed a narcotic, and
4950𓀴coming over to the bed, said cheerily:--
4951𓀴
4952𓀴“Now, little miss, here is your medicine. Drink it off, like a good
4953𓀴child. See, I lift you so that to swallow is easy. Yes.” She had made
4954𓀴the effort with success.
4955𓀴
4956𓀴It astonished me how long the drug took to act. This, in fact, marked
4957𓀴the extent of her weakness. The time seemed endless until sleep began to
4958𓀴flicker in her eyelids. At last, however, the narcotic began to manifest
4959𓀴its potency; and she fell into a deep sleep. When the Professor was
4960𓀴satisfied he called Arthur into the room, and bade him strip off his
4961𓀴coat. Then he added: “You may take that one little kiss whiles I bring
4962𓀴over the table. Friend John, help to me!” So neither of us looked whilst
4963𓀴he bent over her.
4964𓀴
4965𓀴Van Helsing turning to me, said:
4966𓀴
4967𓀴“He is so young and strong and of blood so pure that we need not
4968𓀴defibrinate it.”
4969𓀴
4970𓀴Then with swiftness, but with absolute method, Van Helsing performed the
4971𓀴operation. As the transfusion went on something like life seemed to come
4972𓀴back to poor Lucy’s cheeks, and through Arthur’s growing pallor the joy
4973𓀴of his face seemed absolutely to shine. After a bit I began to grow
4974𓀴anxious, for the loss of blood was telling on Arthur, strong man as he
4975𓀴was. It gave me an idea of what a terrible strain Lucy’s system must
4976𓀴have undergone that what weakened Arthur only partially restored her.
4977𓀴But the Professor’s face was set, and he stood watch in hand and with
4978𓀴his eyes fixed now on the patient and now on Arthur. I could hear my own
4979𓀴heart beat. Presently he said in a soft voice: “Do not stir an instant.
4980𓀴It is enough. You attend him; I will look to her.” When all was over I
4981𓀴could see how much Arthur was weakened. I dressed the wound and took his
4982𓀴arm to bring him away, when Van Helsing spoke without turning round--the
4983𓀴man seems to have eyes in the back of his head:--
4984𓀴
4985𓀴“The brave lover, I think, deserve another kiss, which he shall have
4986𓀴presently.” And as he had now finished his operation, he adjusted the
4987𓀴pillow to the patient’s head. As he did so the narrow black velvet band
4988𓀴which she seems always to wear round her throat, buckled with an old
4989𓀴diamond buckle which her lover had given her, was dragged a little up,
4990𓀴and showed a red mark on her throat. Arthur did not notice it, but I
4991𓀴could hear the deep hiss of indrawn breath which is one of Van Helsing’s
4992𓀴ways of betraying emotion. He said nothing at the moment, but turned to
4993𓀴me, saying: “Now take down our brave young lover, give him of the port
4994𓀴wine, and let him lie down a while. He must then go home and rest, sleep
4995𓀴much and eat much, that he may be recruited of what he has so given to
4996𓀴his love. He must not stay here. Hold! a moment. I may take it, sir,
4997𓀴that you are anxious of result. Then bring it with you that in all ways
4998𓀴the operation is successful. You have saved her life this time, and you
4999𓀴can go home and rest easy in mind that all that can be is. I shall tell
5000𓀴her all when she is well; she shall love you none the less for what you
5001𓀴have done. Good-bye.”
5002𓀴
5003𓀴When Arthur had gone I went back to the room. Lucy was sleeping gently,
5004𓀴but her breathing was stronger; I could see the counterpane move as her
5005𓀴breast heaved. By the bedside sat Van Helsing, looking at her intently.
5006𓀴The velvet band again covered the red mark. I asked the Professor in a
5007𓀴whisper:--
5008𓀴
5009𓀴“What do you make of that mark on her throat?”
5010𓀴
5011𓀴“What do you make of it?”
5012𓀴
5013𓀴“I have not examined it yet,” I answered, and then and there proceeded
5014𓀴to loose the band. Just over the external jugular vein there were two
5015𓀴punctures, not large, but not wholesome-looking. There was no sign of
5016𓀴disease, but the edges were white and worn-looking, as if by some
5017𓀴trituration. It at once occurred to me that this wound, or whatever it
5018𓀴was, might be the means of that manifest loss of blood; but I abandoned
5019𓀴the idea as soon as formed, for such a thing could not be. The whole bed
5020𓀴would have been drenched to a scarlet with the blood which the girl must
5021𓀴have lost to leave such a pallor as she had before the transfusion.
5022𓀴
5023𓀴“Well?” said Van Helsing.
5024𓀴
5025𓀴“Well,” said I, “I can make nothing of it.” The Professor stood up. “I
5026𓀴must go back to Amsterdam to-night,” he said. “There are books and
5027𓀴things there which I want. You must remain here all the night, and you
5028𓀴must not let your sight pass from her.”
5029𓀴
5030𓀴“Shall I have a nurse?” I asked.
5031𓀴
5032𓀴“We are the best nurses, you and I. You keep watch all night; see that
5033𓀴she is well fed, and that nothing disturbs her. You must not sleep all
5034𓀴the night. Later on we can sleep, you and I. I shall be back as soon as
5035𓀴possible. And then we may begin.”
5036𓀴
5037𓀴“May begin?” I said. “What on earth do you mean?”
5038𓀴
5039𓀴“We shall see!” he answered, as he hurried out. He came back a moment
5040𓀴later and put his head inside the door and said with warning finger held
5041𓀴up:--
5042𓀴
5043𓀴“Remember, she is your charge. If you leave her, and harm befall, you
5044𓀴shall not sleep easy hereafter!”
5045𓀴
5046𓀴
5047𓀴_Dr. Seward’s Diary--continued._
5048𓀴
5049𓀴_8 September._--I sat up all night with Lucy. The opiate worked itself
5050𓀴off towards dusk, and she waked naturally; she looked a different being
5051𓀴from what she had been before the operation. Her spirits even were good,
5052𓀴and she was full of a happy vivacity, but I could see evidences of the
5053𓀴absolute prostration which she had undergone. When I told Mrs. Westenra
5054𓀴that Dr. Van Helsing had directed that I should sit up with her she
5055𓀴almost pooh-poohed the idea, pointing out her daughter’s renewed
5056𓀴strength and excellent spirits. I was firm, however, and made
5057𓀴preparations for my long vigil. When her maid had prepared her for the
5058𓀴night I came in, having in the meantime had supper, and took a seat by
5059𓀴the bedside. She did not in any way make objection, but looked at me
5060𓀴gratefully whenever I caught her eye. After a long spell she seemed
5061𓀴sinking off to sleep, but with an effort seemed to pull herself together
5062𓀴and shook it off. This was repeated several times, with greater effort
5063𓀴and with shorter pauses as the time moved on. It was apparent that she
5064𓀴did not want to sleep, so I tackled the subject at once:--
5065𓀴
5066𓀴“You do not want to go to sleep?”
5067𓀴
5068𓀴“No; I am afraid.”
5069𓀴
5070𓀴“Afraid to go to sleep! Why so? It is the boon we all crave for.”
5071𓀴
5072𓀴“Ah, not if you were like me--if sleep was to you a presage of horror!”
5073𓀴
5074𓀴“A presage of horror! What on earth do you mean?”
5075𓀴
5076𓀴“I don’t know; oh, I don’t know. And that is what is so terrible. All
5077𓀴this weakness comes to me in sleep; until I dread the very thought.”
5078𓀴
5079𓀴“But, my dear girl, you may sleep to-night. I am here watching you, and
5080𓀴I can promise that nothing will happen.”
5081𓀴
5082𓀴“Ah, I can trust you!” I seized the opportunity, and said: “I promise
5083𓀴you that if I see any evidence of bad dreams I will wake you at once.”
5084𓀴
5085𓀴“You will? Oh, will you really? How good you are to me. Then I will
5086𓀴sleep!” And almost at the word she gave a deep sigh of relief, and sank
5087𓀴back, asleep.
5088𓀴
5089𓀴All night long I watched by her. She never stirred, but slept on and on
5090𓀴in a deep, tranquil, life-giving, health-giving sleep. Her lips were
5091𓀴slightly parted, and her breast rose and fell with the regularity of a
5092𓀴pendulum. There was a smile on her face, and it was evident that no bad
5093𓀴dreams had come to disturb her peace of mind.
5094𓀴
5095𓀴In the early morning her maid came, and I left her in her care and took
5096𓀴myself back home, for I was anxious about many things. I sent a short
5097𓀴wire to Van Helsing and to Arthur, telling them of the excellent result
5098𓀴of the operation. My own work, with its manifold arrears, took me all
5099𓀴day to clear off; it was dark when I was able to inquire about my
5100𓀴zoöphagous patient. The report was good; he had been quite quiet for the
5101𓀴past day and night. A telegram came from Van Helsing at Amsterdam whilst
5102𓀴I was at dinner, suggesting that I should be at Hillingham to-night, as
5103𓀴it might be well to be at hand, and stating that he was leaving by the
5104𓀴night mail and would join me early in the morning.
5105𓀴
5106𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
5107𓀴
5108𓀴_9 September_.--I was pretty tired and worn out when I got to
5109𓀴Hillingham. For two nights I had hardly had a wink of sleep, and my
5110𓀴brain was beginning to feel that numbness which marks cerebral
5111𓀴exhaustion. Lucy was up and in cheerful spirits. When she shook hands
5112𓀴with me she looked sharply in my face and said:--
5113𓀴
5114𓀴“No sitting up to-night for you. You are worn out. I am quite well
5115𓀴again; indeed, I am; and if there is to be any sitting up, it is I who
5116𓀴will sit up with you.” I would not argue the point, but went and had my
5117𓀴supper. Lucy came with me, and, enlivened by her charming presence, I
5118𓀴made an excellent meal, and had a couple of glasses of the more than
5119𓀴excellent port. Then Lucy took me upstairs, and showed me a room next
5120𓀴her own, where a cozy fire was burning. “Now,” she said, “you must stay
5121𓀴here. I shall leave this door open and my door too. You can lie on the
5122𓀴sofa for I know that nothing would induce any of you doctors to go to
5123𓀴bed whilst there is a patient above the horizon. If I want anything I
5124𓀴shall call out, and you can come to me at once.” I could not but
5125𓀴acquiesce, for I was “dog-tired,” and could not have sat up had I tried.
5126𓀴So, on her renewing her promise to call me if she should want anything,
5127𓀴I lay on the sofa, and forgot all about everything.
5128𓀴
5129𓀴
5130𓀴_Lucy Westenra’s Diary._
5131𓀴
5132𓀴_9 September._--I feel so happy to-night. I have been so miserably weak,
5133𓀴that to be able to think and move about is like feeling sunshine after
5134𓀴a long spell of east wind out of a steel sky. Somehow Arthur feels very,
5135𓀴very close to me. I seem to feel his presence warm about me. I suppose
5136𓀴it is that sickness and weakness are selfish things and turn our inner
5137𓀴eyes and sympathy on ourselves, whilst health and strength give Love
5138𓀴rein, and in thought and feeling he can wander where he wills. I know
5139𓀴where my thoughts are. If Arthur only knew! My dear, my dear, your ears
5140𓀴must tingle as you sleep, as mine do waking. Oh, the blissful rest of
5141𓀴last night! How I slept, with that dear, good Dr. Seward watching me.
5142𓀴And to-night I shall not fear to sleep, since he is close at hand and
5143𓀴within call. Thank everybody for being so good to me! Thank God!
5144𓀴Good-night, Arthur.
5145𓀴
5146𓀴
5147𓀴_Dr. Seward’s Diary._
5148𓀴
5149𓀴_10 September._--I was conscious of the Professor’s hand on my head, and
5150𓀴started awake all in a second. That is one of the things that we learn
5151𓀴in an asylum, at any rate.
5152𓀴
5153𓀴“And how is our patient?”
5154𓀴
5155𓀴“Well, when I left her, or rather when she left me,” I answered.
5156𓀴
5157𓀴“Come, let us see,” he said. And together we went into the room.
5158𓀴
5159𓀴The blind was down, and I went over to raise it gently, whilst Van
5160𓀴Helsing stepped, with his soft, cat-like tread, over to the bed.
5161𓀴
5162𓀴As I raised the blind, and the morning sunlight flooded the room, I
5163𓀴heard the Professor’s low hiss of inspiration, and knowing its rarity, a
5164𓀴deadly fear shot through my heart. As I passed over he moved back, and
5165𓀴his exclamation of horror, “Gott in Himmel!” needed no enforcement from
5166𓀴his agonised face. He raised his hand and pointed to the bed, and his
5167𓀴iron face was drawn and ashen white. I felt my knees begin to tremble.
5168𓀴
5169𓀴There on the bed, seemingly in a swoon, lay poor Lucy, more horribly
5170𓀴white and wan-looking than ever. Even the lips were white, and the gums
5171𓀴seemed to have shrunken back from the teeth, as we sometimes see in a
5172𓀴corpse after a prolonged illness. Van Helsing raised his foot to stamp
5173𓀴in anger, but the instinct of his life and all the long years of habit
5174𓀴stood to him, and he put it down again softly. “Quick!” he said. “Bring
5175𓀴the brandy.” I flew to the dining-room, and returned with the decanter.
5176𓀴He wetted the poor white lips with it, and together we rubbed palm and
5177𓀴wrist and heart. He felt her heart, and after a few moments of agonising
5178𓀴suspense said:--
5179𓀴
5180𓀴“It is not too late. It beats, though but feebly. All our work is
5181𓀴undone; we must begin again. There is no young Arthur here now; I have
5182𓀴to call on you yourself this time, friend John.” As he spoke, he was
5183𓀴dipping into his bag and producing the instruments for transfusion; I
5184𓀴had taken off my coat and rolled up my shirt-sleeve. There was no
5185𓀴possibility of an opiate just at present, and no need of one; and so,
5186𓀴without a moment’s delay, we began the operation. After a time--it did
5187𓀴not seem a short time either, for the draining away of one’s blood, no
5188𓀴matter how willingly it be given, is a terrible feeling--Van Helsing
5189𓀴held up a warning finger. “Do not stir,” he said, “but I fear that with
5190𓀴growing strength she may wake; and that would make danger, oh, so much
5191𓀴danger. But I shall precaution take. I shall give hypodermic injection
5192𓀴of morphia.” He proceeded then, swiftly and deftly, to carry out his
5193𓀴intent. The effect on Lucy was not bad, for the faint seemed to merge
5194𓀴subtly into the narcotic sleep. It was with a feeling of personal pride
5195𓀴that I could see a faint tinge of colour steal back into the pallid
5196𓀴cheeks and lips. No man knows, till he experiences it, what it is to
5197𓀴feel his own life-blood drawn away into the veins of the woman he loves.
5198𓀴
5199𓀴The Professor watched me critically. “That will do,” he said. “Already?”
5200𓀴I remonstrated. “You took a great deal more from Art.” To which he
5201𓀴smiled a sad sort of smile as he replied:--
5202𓀴
5203𓀴“He is her lover, her _fiancé_. You have work, much work, to do for her
5204𓀴and for others; and the present will suffice.”
5205𓀴
5206𓀴When we stopped the operation, he attended to Lucy, whilst I applied
5207𓀴digital pressure to my own incision. I laid down, whilst I waited his
5208𓀴leisure to attend to me, for I felt faint and a little sick. By-and-by
5209𓀴he bound up my wound, and sent me downstairs to get a glass of wine for
5210𓀴myself. As I was leaving the room, he came after me, and half
5211𓀴whispered:--
5212𓀴
5213𓀴“Mind, nothing must be said of this. If our young lover should turn up
5214𓀴unexpected, as before, no word to him. It would at once frighten him and
5215𓀴enjealous him, too. There must be none. So!”
5216𓀴
5217𓀴When I came back he looked at me carefully, and then said:--
5218𓀴
5219𓀴“You are not much the worse. Go into the room, and lie on your sofa, and
5220𓀴rest awhile; then have much breakfast, and come here to me.”
5221𓀴
5222𓀴I followed out his orders, for I knew how right and wise they were. I
5223𓀴had done my part, and now my next duty was to keep up my strength. I
5224𓀴felt very weak, and in the weakness lost something of the amazement at
5225𓀴what had occurred. I fell asleep on the sofa, however, wondering over
5226𓀴and over again how Lucy had made such a retrograde movement, and how
5227𓀴she could have been drained of so much blood with no sign anywhere to
5228𓀴show for it. I think I must have continued my wonder in my dreams, for,
5229𓀴sleeping and waking, my thoughts always came back to the little
5230𓀴punctures in her throat and the ragged, exhausted appearance of their
5231𓀴edges--tiny though they were.
5232𓀴
5233𓀴Lucy slept well into the day, and when she woke she was fairly well and
5234𓀴strong, though not nearly so much so as the day before. When Van Helsing
5235𓀴had seen her, he went out for a walk, leaving me in charge, with strict
5236𓀴injunctions that I was not to leave her for a moment. I could hear his
5237𓀴voice in the hall, asking the way to the nearest telegraph office.
5238𓀴
5239𓀴Lucy chatted with me freely, and seemed quite unconscious that anything
5240𓀴had happened. I tried to keep her amused and interested. When her mother
5241𓀴came up to see her, she did not seem to notice any change whatever, but
5242𓀴said to me gratefully:--
5243𓀴
5244𓀴“We owe you so much, Dr. Seward, for all you have done, but you really
5245𓀴must now take care not to overwork yourself. You are looking pale
5246𓀴yourself. You want a wife to nurse and look after you a bit; that you
5247𓀴do!” As she spoke, Lucy turned crimson, though it was only momentarily,
5248𓀴for her poor wasted veins could not stand for long such an unwonted
5249𓀴drain to the head. The reaction came in excessive pallor as she turned
5250𓀴imploring eyes on me. I smiled and nodded, and laid my finger on my
5251𓀴lips; with a sigh, she sank back amid her pillows.
5252𓀴
5253𓀴Van Helsing returned in a couple of hours, and presently said to me:
5254𓀴“Now you go home, and eat much and drink enough. Make yourself strong. I
5255𓀴stay here to-night, and I shall sit up with little miss myself. You and
5256𓀴I must watch the case, and we must have none other to know. I have grave
5257𓀴reasons. No, do not ask them; think what you will. Do not fear to think
5258𓀴even the most not-probable. Good-night.”
5259𓀴
5260𓀴In the hall two of the maids came to me, and asked if they or either of
5261𓀴them might not sit up with Miss Lucy. They implored me to let them; and
5262𓀴when I said it was Dr. Van Helsing’s wish that either he or I should sit
5263𓀴up, they asked me quite piteously to intercede with the “foreign
5264𓀴gentleman.” I was much touched by their kindness. Perhaps it is because
5265𓀴I am weak at present, and perhaps because it was on Lucy’s account, that
5266𓀴their devotion was manifested; for over and over again have I seen
5267𓀴similar instances of woman’s kindness. I got back here in time for a
5268𓀴late dinner; went my rounds--all well; and set this down whilst waiting
5269𓀴for sleep. It is coming.
5270𓀴
5271𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
5272𓀴
5273𓀴_11 September._--This afternoon I went over to Hillingham. Found Van
5274𓀴Helsing in excellent spirits, and Lucy much better. Shortly after I had
5275𓀴arrived, a big parcel from abroad came for the Professor. He opened it
5276𓀴with much impressment--assumed, of course--and showed a great bundle of
5277𓀴white flowers.
5278𓀴
5279𓀴“These are for you, Miss Lucy,” he said.
5280𓀴
5281𓀴“For me? Oh, Dr. Van Helsing!”
5282𓀴
5283𓀴“Yes, my dear, but not for you to play with. These are medicines.” Here
5284𓀴Lucy made a wry face. “Nay, but they are not to take in a decoction or
5285𓀴in nauseous form, so you need not snub that so charming nose, or I shall
5286𓀴point out to my friend Arthur what woes he may have to endure in seeing
5287𓀴so much beauty that he so loves so much distort. Aha, my pretty miss,
5288𓀴that bring the so nice nose all straight again. This is medicinal, but
5289𓀴you do not know how. I put him in your window, I make pretty wreath, and
5290𓀴hang him round your neck, so that you sleep well. Oh yes! they, like the
5291𓀴lotus flower, make your trouble forgotten. It smell so like the waters
5292𓀴of Lethe, and of that fountain of youth that the Conquistadores sought
5293𓀴for in the Floridas, and find him all too late.”
5294𓀴
5295𓀴Whilst he was speaking, Lucy had been examining the flowers and smelling
5296𓀴them. Now she threw them down, saying, with half-laughter, and
5297𓀴half-disgust:--
5298𓀴
5299𓀴“Oh, Professor, I believe you are only putting up a joke on me. Why,
5300𓀴these flowers are only common garlic.”
5301𓀴
5302𓀴To my surprise, Van Helsing rose up and said with all his sternness, his
5303𓀴iron jaw set and his bushy eyebrows meeting:--
5304𓀴
5305𓀴“No trifling with me! I never jest! There is grim purpose in all I do;
5306𓀴and I warn you that you do not thwart me. Take care, for the sake of
5307𓀴others if not for your own.” Then seeing poor Lucy scared, as she might
5308𓀴well be, he went on more gently: “Oh, little miss, my dear, do not fear
5309𓀴me. I only do for your good; but there is much virtue to you in those so
5310𓀴common flowers. See, I place them myself in your room. I make myself the
5311𓀴wreath that you are to wear. But hush! no telling to others that make so
5312𓀴inquisitive questions. We must obey, and silence is a part of obedience;
5313𓀴and obedience is to bring you strong and well into loving arms that wait
5314𓀴for you. Now sit still awhile. Come with me, friend John, and you shall
5315𓀴help me deck the room with my garlic, which is all the way from Haarlem,
5316𓀴where my friend Vanderpool raise herb in his glass-houses all the year.
5317𓀴I had to telegraph yesterday, or they would not have been here.”
5318𓀴
5319𓀴We went into the room, taking the flowers with us. The Professor’s
5320𓀴actions were certainly odd and not to be found in any pharmacopoeia
5321𓀴that I ever heard of. First he fastened up the windows and latched them
5322𓀴securely; next, taking a handful of the flowers, he rubbed them all over
5323𓀴the sashes, as though to ensure that every whiff of air that might get
5324𓀴in would be laden with the garlic smell. Then with the wisp he rubbed
5325𓀴all over the jamb of the door, above, below, and at each side, and round
5326𓀴the fireplace in the same way. It all seemed grotesque to me, and
5327𓀴presently I said:--
5328𓀴
5329𓀴“Well, Professor, I know you always have a reason for what you do, but
5330𓀴this certainly puzzles me. It is well we have no sceptic here, or he
5331𓀴would say that you were working some spell to keep out an evil spirit.”
5332𓀴
5333𓀴“Perhaps I am!” he answered quietly as he began to make the wreath which
5334𓀴Lucy was to wear round her neck.
5335𓀴
5336𓀴We then waited whilst Lucy made her toilet for the night, and when she
5337𓀴was in bed he came and himself fixed the wreath of garlic round her
5338𓀴neck. The last words he said to her were:--
5339𓀴
5340𓀴“Take care you do not disturb it; and even if the room feel close, do
5341𓀴not to-night open the window or the door.”
5342𓀴
5343𓀴“I promise,” said Lucy, “and thank you both a thousand times for all
5344𓀴your kindness to me! Oh, what have I done to be blessed with such
5345𓀴friends?”
5346𓀴
5347𓀴As we left the house in my fly, which was waiting, Van Helsing said:--
5348𓀴
5349𓀴“To-night I can sleep in peace, and sleep I want--two nights of travel,
5350𓀴much reading in the day between, and much anxiety on the day to follow,
5351𓀴and a night to sit up, without to wink. To-morrow in the morning early
5352𓀴you call for me, and we come together to see our pretty miss, so much
5353𓀴more strong for my ‘spell’ which I have work. Ho! ho!”
5354𓀴
5355𓀴He seemed so confident that I, remembering my own confidence two nights
5356𓀴before and with the baneful result, felt awe and vague terror. It must
5357𓀴have been my weakness that made me hesitate to tell it to my friend, but
5358𓀴I felt it all the more, like unshed tears.
5359𓀴
5360𓀴
5361𓀴
5362𓀴
5363𓀴CHAPTER XI
5364𓀴
5365𓀴_Lucy Westenra’s Diary._
5366𓀴
5367𓀴
5368𓀴_12 September._--How good they all are to me. I quite love that dear Dr.
5369𓀴Van Helsing. I wonder why he was so anxious about these flowers. He
5370𓀴positively frightened me, he was so fierce. And yet he must have been
5371𓀴right, for I feel comfort from them already. Somehow, I do not dread
5372𓀴being alone to-night, and I can go to sleep without fear. I shall not
5373𓀴mind any flapping outside the window. Oh, the terrible struggle that I
5374𓀴have had against sleep so often of late; the pain of the sleeplessness,
5375𓀴or the pain of the fear of sleep, with such unknown horrors as it has
5376𓀴for me! How blessed are some people, whose lives have no fears, no
