5377𓀴dreads; to whom sleep is a blessing that comes nightly, and brings
5378𓀴nothing but sweet dreams. Well, here I am to-night, hoping for sleep,
5379𓀴and lying like Ophelia in the play, with “virgin crants and maiden
5380𓀴strewments.” I never liked garlic before, but to-night it is delightful!
5381𓀴There is peace in its smell; I feel sleep coming already. Good-night,
5382𓀴everybody.
5383𓀴
5384𓀴
5385𓀴_Dr. Seward’s Diary._
5386𓀴
5387𓀴_13 September._--Called at the Berkeley and found Van Helsing, as usual,
5388𓀴up to time. The carriage ordered from the hotel was waiting. The
5389𓀴Professor took his bag, which he always brings with him now.
5390𓀴
5391𓀴Let all be put down exactly. Van Helsing and I arrived at Hillingham at
5392𓀴eight o’clock. It was a lovely morning; the bright sunshine and all the
5393𓀴fresh feeling of early autumn seemed like the completion of nature’s
5394𓀴annual work. The leaves were turning to all kinds of beautiful colours,
5395𓀴but had not yet begun to drop from the trees. When we entered we met
5396𓀴Mrs. Westenra coming out of the morning room. She is always an early
5397𓀴riser. She greeted us warmly and said:--
5398𓀴
5399𓀴“You will be glad to know that Lucy is better. The dear child is still
5400𓀴asleep. I looked into her room and saw her, but did not go in, lest I
5401𓀴should disturb her.” The Professor smiled, and looked quite jubilant. He
5402𓀴rubbed his hands together, and said:--
5403𓀴
5404𓀴“Aha! I thought I had diagnosed the case. My treatment is working,” to
5405𓀴which she answered:--
5406𓀴
5407𓀴“You must not take all the credit to yourself, doctor. Lucy’s state this
5408𓀴morning is due in part to me.”
5409𓀴
5410𓀴“How you do mean, ma’am?” asked the Professor.
5411𓀴
5412𓀴“Well, I was anxious about the dear child in the night, and went into
5413𓀴her room. She was sleeping soundly--so soundly that even my coming did
5414𓀴not wake her. But the room was awfully stuffy. There were a lot of those
5415𓀴horrible, strong-smelling flowers about everywhere, and she had actually
5416𓀴a bunch of them round her neck. I feared that the heavy odour would be
5417𓀴too much for the dear child in her weak state, so I took them all away
5418𓀴and opened a bit of the window to let in a little fresh air. You will be
5419𓀴pleased with her, I am sure.”
5420𓀴
5421𓀴She moved off into her boudoir, where she usually breakfasted early. As
5422𓀴she had spoken, I watched the Professor’s face, and saw it turn ashen
5423𓀴grey. He had been able to retain his self-command whilst the poor lady
5424𓀴was present, for he knew her state and how mischievous a shock would be;
5425𓀴he actually smiled on her as he held open the door for her to pass into
5426𓀴her room. But the instant she had disappeared he pulled me, suddenly and
5427𓀴forcibly, into the dining-room and closed the door.
5428𓀴
5429𓀴Then, for the first time in my life, I saw Van Helsing break down. He
5430𓀴raised his hands over his head in a sort of mute despair, and then beat
5431𓀴his palms together in a helpless way; finally he sat down on a chair,
5432𓀴and putting his hands before his face, began to sob, with loud, dry sobs
5433𓀴that seemed to come from the very racking of his heart. Then he raised
5434𓀴his arms again, as though appealing to the whole universe. “God! God!
5435𓀴God!” he said. “What have we done, what has this poor thing done, that
5436𓀴we are so sore beset? Is there fate amongst us still, sent down from the
5437𓀴pagan world of old, that such things must be, and in such way? This poor
5438𓀴mother, all unknowing, and all for the best as she think, does such
5439𓀴thing as lose her daughter body and soul; and we must not tell her, we
5440𓀴must not even warn her, or she die, and then both die. Oh, how we are
5441𓀴beset! How are all the powers of the devils against us!” Suddenly he
5442𓀴jumped to his feet. “Come,” he said, “come, we must see and act. Devils
5443𓀴or no devils, or all the devils at once, it matters not; we fight him
5444𓀴all the same.” He went to the hall-door for his bag; and together we
5445𓀴went up to Lucy’s room.
5446𓀴
5447𓀴Once again I drew up the blind, whilst Van Helsing went towards the bed.
5448𓀴This time he did not start as he looked on the poor face with the same
5449𓀴awful, waxen pallor as before. He wore a look of stern sadness and
5450𓀴infinite pity.
5451𓀴
5452𓀴“As I expected,” he murmured, with that hissing inspiration of his which
5453𓀴meant so much. Without a word he went and locked the door, and then
5454𓀴began to set out on the little table the instruments for yet another
5455𓀴operation of transfusion of blood. I had long ago recognised the
5456𓀴necessity, and begun to take off my coat, but he stopped me with a
5457𓀴warning hand. “No!” he said. “To-day you must operate. I shall provide.
5458𓀴You are weakened already.” As he spoke he took off his coat and rolled
5459𓀴up his shirt-sleeve.
5460𓀴
5461𓀴Again the operation; again the narcotic; again some return of colour to
5462𓀴the ashy cheeks, and the regular breathing of healthy sleep. This time I
5463𓀴watched whilst Van Helsing recruited himself and rested.
5464𓀴
5465𓀴Presently he took an opportunity of telling Mrs. Westenra that she must
5466𓀴not remove anything from Lucy’s room without consulting him; that the
5467𓀴flowers were of medicinal value, and that the breathing of their odour
5468𓀴was a part of the system of cure. Then he took over the care of the case
5469𓀴himself, saying that he would watch this night and the next and would
5470𓀴send me word when to come.
5471𓀴
5472𓀴After another hour Lucy waked from her sleep, fresh and bright and
5473𓀴seemingly not much the worse for her terrible ordeal.
5474𓀴
5475𓀴What does it all mean? I am beginning to wonder if my long habit of life
5476𓀴amongst the insane is beginning to tell upon my own brain.
5477𓀴
5478𓀴
5479𓀴_Lucy Westenra’s Diary._
5480𓀴
5481𓀴_17 September._--Four days and nights of peace. I am getting so strong
5482𓀴again that I hardly know myself. It is as if I had passed through some
5483𓀴long nightmare, and had just awakened to see the beautiful sunshine and
5484𓀴feel the fresh air of the morning around me. I have a dim
5485𓀴half-remembrance of long, anxious times of waiting and fearing; darkness
5486𓀴in which there was not even the pain of hope to make present distress
5487𓀴more poignant: and then long spells of oblivion, and the rising back to
5488𓀴life as a diver coming up through a great press of water. Since,
5489𓀴however, Dr. Van Helsing has been with me, all this bad dreaming seems
5490𓀴to have passed away; the noises that used to frighten me out of my
5491𓀴wits--the flapping against the windows, the distant voices which seemed
5492𓀴so close to me, the harsh sounds that came from I know not where and
5493𓀴commanded me to do I know not what--have all ceased. I go to bed now
5494𓀴without any fear of sleep. I do not even try to keep awake. I have grown
5495𓀴quite fond of the garlic, and a boxful arrives for me every day from
5496𓀴Haarlem. To-night Dr. Van Helsing is going away, as he has to be for a
5497𓀴day in Amsterdam. But I need not be watched; I am well enough to be left
5498𓀴alone. Thank God for mother’s sake, and dear Arthur’s, and for all our
5499𓀴friends who have been so kind! I shall not even feel the change, for
5500𓀴last night Dr. Van Helsing slept in his chair a lot of the time. I found
5501𓀴him asleep twice when I awoke; but I did not fear to go to sleep again,
5502𓀴although the boughs or bats or something napped almost angrily against
5503𓀴the window-panes.
5504𓀴
5505𓀴
5506𓀴_“The Pall Mall Gazette,” 18 September._
5507𓀴
5508𓀴                           THE ESCAPED WOLF.
5509𓀴
5510𓀴         PERILOUS ADVENTURE OF OUR INTERVIEWER.
5511𓀴
5512𓀴         _Interview with the Keeper in the Zoölogical Gardens._
5513𓀴
5514𓀴After many inquiries and almost as many refusals, and perpetually using
5515𓀴the words “Pall Mall Gazette” as a sort of talisman, I managed to find
5516𓀴the keeper of the section of the Zoölogical Gardens in which the wolf
5517𓀴department is included. Thomas Bilder lives in one of the cottages in
5518𓀴the enclosure behind the elephant-house, and was just sitting down to
5519𓀴his tea when I found him. Thomas and his wife are hospitable folk,
5520𓀴elderly, and without children, and if the specimen I enjoyed of their
5521𓀴hospitality be of the average kind, their lives must be pretty
5522𓀴comfortable. The keeper would not enter on what he called “business”
5523𓀴until the supper was over, and we were all satisfied. Then when the
5524𓀴table was cleared, and he had lit his pipe, he said:--
5525𓀴
5526𓀴“Now, sir, you can go on and arsk me what you want. You’ll excoose me
5527𓀴refoosin’ to talk of perfeshunal subjects afore meals. I gives the
5528𓀴wolves and the jackals and the hyenas in all our section their tea afore
5529𓀴I begins to arsk them questions.”
5530𓀴
5531𓀴“How do you mean, ask them questions?” I queried, wishful to get him
5532𓀴into a talkative humour.
5533𓀴
5534𓀴“’Ittin’ of them over the ’ead with a pole is one way; scratchin’ of
5535𓀴their hears is another, when gents as is flush wants a bit of a show-orf
5536𓀴to their gals. I don’t so much mind the fust--the ’ittin’ with a pole
5537𓀴afore I chucks in their dinner; but I waits till they’ve ’ad their
5538𓀴sherry and kawffee, so to speak, afore I tries on with the
5539𓀴ear-scratchin’. Mind you,” he added philosophically, “there’s a deal of
5540𓀴the same nature in us as in them theer animiles. Here’s you a-comin’ and
5541𓀴arskin’ of me questions about my business, and I that grumpy-like that
5542𓀴only for your bloomin’ ’arf-quid I’d ’a’ seen you blowed fust ’fore I’d
5543𓀴answer. Not even when you arsked me sarcastic-like if I’d like you to
5544𓀴arsk the Superintendent if you might arsk me questions. Without offence
5545𓀴did I tell yer to go to ’ell?”
5546𓀴
5547𓀴“You did.”
5548𓀴
5549𓀴“An’ when you said you’d report me for usin’ of obscene language that
5550𓀴was ’ittin’ me over the ’ead; but the ’arf-quid made that all right. I
5551𓀴weren’t a-goin’ to fight, so I waited for the food, and did with my ’owl
5552𓀴as the wolves, and lions, and tigers does. But, Lor’ love yer ’art, now
5553𓀴that the old ’ooman has stuck a chunk of her tea-cake in me, an’ rinsed
5554𓀴me out with her bloomin’ old teapot, and I’ve lit hup, you may scratch
5555𓀴my ears for all you’re worth, and won’t git even a growl out of me.
5556𓀴Drive along with your questions. I know what yer a-comin’ at, that ’ere
5557𓀴escaped wolf.”
5558𓀴
5559𓀴“Exactly. I want you to give me your view of it. Just tell me how it
5560𓀴happened; and when I know the facts I’ll get you to say what you
5561𓀴consider was the cause of it, and how you think the whole affair will
5562𓀴end.”
5563𓀴
5564𓀴“All right, guv’nor. This ’ere is about the ’ole story. That ’ere wolf
5565𓀴what we called Bersicker was one of three grey ones that came from
5566𓀴Norway to Jamrach’s, which we bought off him four years ago. He was a
5567𓀴nice well-behaved wolf, that never gave no trouble to talk of. I’m more
5568𓀴surprised at ’im for wantin’ to get out nor any other animile in the
5569𓀴place. But, there, you can’t trust wolves no more nor women.”
5570𓀴
5571𓀴“Don’t you mind him, sir!” broke in Mrs. Tom, with a cheery laugh. “’E’s
5572𓀴got mindin’ the animiles so long that blest if he ain’t like a old wolf
5573𓀴’isself! But there ain’t no ’arm in ’im.”
5574𓀴
5575𓀴“Well, sir, it was about two hours after feedin’ yesterday when I first
5576𓀴hear my disturbance. I was makin’ up a litter in the monkey-house for a
5577𓀴young puma which is ill; but when I heard the yelpin’ and ’owlin’ I kem
5578𓀴away straight. There was Bersicker a-tearin’ like a mad thing at the
5579𓀴bars as if he wanted to get out. There wasn’t much people about that
5580𓀴day, and close at hand was only one man, a tall, thin chap, with a ’ook
5581𓀴nose and a pointed beard, with a few white hairs runnin’ through it. He
5582𓀴had a ’ard, cold look and red eyes, and I took a sort of mislike to him,
5583𓀴for it seemed as if it was ’im as they was hirritated at. He ’ad white
5584𓀴kid gloves on ’is ’ands, and he pointed out the animiles to me and says:
5585𓀴‘Keeper, these wolves seem upset at something.’
5586𓀴
5587𓀴“‘Maybe it’s you,’ says I, for I did not like the airs as he give
5588𓀴’isself. He didn’t git angry, as I ’oped he would, but he smiled a kind
5589𓀴of insolent smile, with a mouth full of white, sharp teeth. ‘Oh no, they
5590𓀴wouldn’t like me,’ ’e says.
5591𓀴
5592𓀴“‘Ow yes, they would,’ says I, a-imitatin’ of him. ‘They always likes a
5593𓀴bone or two to clean their teeth on about tea-time, which you ’as a
5594𓀴bagful.’
5595𓀴
5596𓀴“Well, it was a odd thing, but when the animiles see us a-talkin’ they
5597𓀴lay down, and when I went over to Bersicker he let me stroke his ears
5598𓀴same as ever. That there man kem over, and blessed but if he didn’t put
5599𓀴in his hand and stroke the old wolf’s ears too!
5600𓀴
5601𓀴“‘Tyke care,’ says I. ‘Bersicker is quick.’
5602𓀴
5603𓀴“‘Never mind,’ he says. ‘I’m used to ’em!’
5604𓀴
5605𓀴“‘Are you in the business yourself?’ I says, tyking off my ’at, for a
5606𓀴man what trades in wolves, anceterer, is a good friend to keepers.
5607𓀴
5608𓀴“‘No,’ says he, ‘not exactly in the business, but I ’ave made pets of
5609𓀴several.’ And with that he lifts his ’at as perlite as a lord, and walks
5610𓀴away. Old Bersicker kep’ a-lookin’ arter ’im till ’e was out of sight,
5611𓀴and then went and lay down in a corner and wouldn’t come hout the ’ole
5612𓀴hevening. Well, larst night, so soon as the moon was hup, the wolves
5613𓀴here all began a-’owling. There warn’t nothing for them to ’owl at.
5614𓀴There warn’t no one near, except some one that was evidently a-callin’ a
5615𓀴dog somewheres out back of the gardings in the Park road. Once or twice
5616𓀴I went out to see that all was right, and it was, and then the ’owling
5617𓀴stopped. Just before twelve o’clock I just took a look round afore
5618𓀴turnin’ in, an’, bust me, but when I kem opposite to old Bersicker’s
5619𓀴cage I see the rails broken and twisted about and the cage empty. And
5620𓀴that’s all I know for certing.”
5621𓀴
5622𓀴“Did any one else see anything?”
5623𓀴
5624𓀴“One of our gard’ners was a-comin’ ’ome about that time from a ’armony,
5625𓀴when he sees a big grey dog comin’ out through the garding ’edges. At
5626𓀴least, so he says, but I don’t give much for it myself, for if he did ’e
5627𓀴never said a word about it to his missis when ’e got ’ome, and it was
5628𓀴only after the escape of the wolf was made known, and we had been up all
5629𓀴night-a-huntin’ of the Park for Bersicker, that he remembered seein’
5630𓀴anything. My own belief was that the ’armony ’ad got into his ’ead.”
5631𓀴
5632𓀴“Now, Mr. Bilder, can you account in any way for the escape of the
5633𓀴wolf?”
5634𓀴
5635𓀴“Well, sir,” he said, with a suspicious sort of modesty, “I think I can;
5636𓀴but I don’t know as ’ow you’d be satisfied with the theory.”
5637𓀴
5638𓀴“Certainly I shall. If a man like you, who knows the animals from
5639𓀴experience, can’t hazard a good guess at any rate, who is even to try?”
5640𓀴
5641𓀴“Well then, sir, I accounts for it this way; it seems to me that ’ere
5642𓀴wolf escaped--simply because he wanted to get out.”
5643𓀴
5644𓀴From the hearty way that both Thomas and his wife laughed at the joke I
5645𓀴could see that it had done service before, and that the whole
5646𓀴explanation was simply an elaborate sell. I couldn’t cope in badinage
5647𓀴with the worthy Thomas, but I thought I knew a surer way to his heart,
5648𓀴so I said:--
5649𓀴
5650𓀴“Now, Mr. Bilder, we’ll consider that first half-sovereign worked off,
5651𓀴and this brother of his is waiting to be claimed when you’ve told me
5652𓀴what you think will happen.”
5653𓀴
5654𓀴“Right y’are, sir,” he said briskly. “Ye’ll excoose me, I know, for
5655𓀴a-chaffin’ of ye, but the old woman here winked at me, which was as much
5656𓀴as telling me to go on.”
5657𓀴
5658𓀴“Well, I never!” said the old lady.
5659𓀴
5660𓀴“My opinion is this: that ’ere wolf is a-’idin’ of, somewheres. The
5661𓀴gard’ner wot didn’t remember said he was a-gallopin’ northward faster
5662𓀴than a horse could go; but I don’t believe him, for, yer see, sir,
5663𓀴wolves don’t gallop no more nor dogs does, they not bein’ built that
5664𓀴way. Wolves is fine things in a storybook, and I dessay when they gets
5665𓀴in packs and does be chivyin’ somethin’ that’s more afeared than they is
5666𓀴they can make a devil of a noise and chop it up, whatever it is. But,
5667𓀴Lor’ bless you, in real life a wolf is only a low creature, not half so
5668𓀴clever or bold as a good dog; and not half a quarter so much fight in
5669𓀴’im. This one ain’t been used to fightin’ or even to providin’ for
5670𓀴hisself, and more like he’s somewhere round the Park a-’idin’ an’
5671𓀴a-shiverin’ of, and, if he thinks at all, wonderin’ where he is to get
5672𓀴his breakfast from; or maybe he’s got down some area and is in a
5673𓀴coal-cellar. My eye, won’t some cook get a rum start when she sees his
5674𓀴green eyes a-shining at her out of the dark! If he can’t get food he’s
5675𓀴bound to look for it, and mayhap he may chance to light on a butcher’s
5676𓀴shop in time. If he doesn’t, and some nursemaid goes a-walkin’ orf with
5677𓀴a soldier, leavin’ of the hinfant in the perambulator--well, then I
5678𓀴shouldn’t be surprised if the census is one babby the less. That’s
5679𓀴all.”
5680𓀴
5681𓀴I was handing him the half-sovereign, when something came bobbing up
5682𓀴against the window, and Mr. Bilder’s face doubled its natural length
5683𓀴with surprise.
5684𓀴
5685𓀴“God bless me!” he said. “If there ain’t old Bersicker come back by
5686𓀴’isself!”
5687𓀴
5688𓀴He went to the door and opened it; a most unnecessary proceeding it
5689𓀴seemed to me. I have always thought that a wild animal never looks so
5690𓀴well as when some obstacle of pronounced durability is between us; a
5691𓀴personal experience has intensified rather than diminished that idea.
5692𓀴
5693𓀴After all, however, there is nothing like custom, for neither Bilder nor
5694𓀴his wife thought any more of the wolf than I should of a dog. The animal
5695𓀴itself was as peaceful and well-behaved as that father of all
5696𓀴picture-wolves--Red Riding Hood’s quondam friend, whilst moving her
5697𓀴confidence in masquerade.
5698𓀴
5699𓀴The whole scene was an unutterable mixture of comedy and pathos. The
5700𓀴wicked wolf that for half a day had paralysed London and set all the
5701𓀴children in the town shivering in their shoes, was there in a sort of
5702𓀴penitent mood, and was received and petted like a sort of vulpine
5703𓀴prodigal son. Old Bilder examined him all over with most tender
5704𓀴solicitude, and when he had finished with his penitent said:--
5705𓀴
5706𓀴“There, I knew the poor old chap would get into some kind of trouble;
5707𓀴didn’t I say it all along? Here’s his head all cut and full of broken
5708𓀴glass. ’E’s been a-gettin’ over some bloomin’ wall or other. It’s a
5709𓀴shyme that people are allowed to top their walls with broken bottles.
5710𓀴This ’ere’s what comes of it. Come along, Bersicker.”
5711𓀴
5712𓀴He took the wolf and locked him up in a cage, with a piece of meat that
5713𓀴satisfied, in quantity at any rate, the elementary conditions of the
5714𓀴fatted calf, and went off to report.
5715𓀴
5716𓀴I came off, too, to report the only exclusive information that is given
5717𓀴to-day regarding the strange escapade at the Zoo.
5718𓀴
5719𓀴
5720𓀴_Dr. Seward’s Diary._
5721𓀴
5722𓀴_17 September._--I was engaged after dinner in my study posting up my
5723𓀴books, which, through press of other work and the many visits to Lucy,
5724𓀴had fallen sadly into arrear. Suddenly the door was burst open, and in
5725𓀴rushed my patient, with his face distorted with passion. I was
5726𓀴thunderstruck, for such a thing as a patient getting of his own accord
5727𓀴into the Superintendent’s study is almost unknown. Without an instant’s
5728𓀴pause he made straight at me. He had a dinner-knife in his hand, and,
5729𓀴as I saw he was dangerous, I tried to keep the table between us. He was
5730𓀴too quick and too strong for me, however; for before I could get my
5731𓀴balance he had struck at me and cut my left wrist rather severely.
5732𓀴Before he could strike again, however, I got in my right and he was
5733𓀴sprawling on his back on the floor. My wrist bled freely, and quite a
5734𓀴little pool trickled on to the carpet. I saw that my friend was not
5735𓀴intent on further effort, and occupied myself binding up my wrist,
5736𓀴keeping a wary eye on the prostrate figure all the time. When the
5737𓀴attendants rushed in, and we turned our attention to him, his employment
5738𓀴positively sickened me. He was lying on his belly on the floor licking
5739𓀴up, like a dog, the blood which had fallen from my wounded wrist. He was
5740𓀴easily secured, and, to my surprise, went with the attendants quite
5741𓀴placidly, simply repeating over and over again: “The blood is the life!
5742𓀴The blood is the life!”
5743𓀴
5744𓀴I cannot afford to lose blood just at present; I have lost too much of
5745𓀴late for my physical good, and then the prolonged strain of Lucy’s
5746𓀴illness and its horrible phases is telling on me. I am over-excited and
5747𓀴weary, and I need rest, rest, rest. Happily Van Helsing has not summoned
5748𓀴me, so I need not forego my sleep; to-night I could not well do without
5749𓀴it.
5750𓀴
5751𓀴
5752𓀴_Telegram, Van Helsing, Antwerp, to Seward, Carfax._
5753𓀴
5754𓀴(Sent to Carfax, Sussex, as no county given; delivered late by
5755𓀴twenty-two hours.)
5756𓀴
5757𓀴“_17 September._--Do not fail to be at Hillingham to-night. If not
5758𓀴watching all the time frequently, visit and see that flowers are as
5759𓀴placed; very important; do not fail. Shall be with you as soon as
5760𓀴possible after arrival.”
5761𓀴
5762𓀴
5763𓀴_Dr. Seward’s Diary._
5764𓀴
5765𓀴_18 September._--Just off for train to London. The arrival of Van
5766𓀴Helsing’s telegram filled me with dismay. A whole night lost, and I know
5767𓀴by bitter experience what may happen in a night. Of course it is
5768𓀴possible that all may be well, but what _may_ have happened? Surely
5769𓀴there is some horrible doom hanging over us that every possible accident
5770𓀴should thwart us in all we try to do. I shall take this cylinder with
5771𓀴me, and then I can complete my entry on Lucy’s phonograph.
5772𓀴
5773𓀴
5774𓀴_Memorandum left by Lucy Westenra._
5775𓀴
5776𓀴_17 September. Night._--I write this and leave it to be seen, so that no
5777𓀴one may by any chance get into trouble through me. This is an exact
5778𓀴record of what took place to-night. I feel I am dying of weakness, and
5779𓀴have barely strength to write, but it must be done if I die in the
5780𓀴doing.
5781𓀴
5782𓀴I went to bed as usual, taking care that the flowers were placed as Dr.
5783𓀴Van Helsing directed, and soon fell asleep.
5784𓀴
5785𓀴I was waked by the flapping at the window, which had begun after that
5786𓀴sleep-walking on the cliff at Whitby when Mina saved me, and which now I
5787𓀴know so well. I was not afraid, but I did wish that Dr. Seward was in
5788𓀴the next room--as Dr. Van Helsing said he would be--so that I might have
5789𓀴called him. I tried to go to sleep, but could not. Then there came to me
5790𓀴the old fear of sleep, and I determined to keep awake. Perversely sleep
5791𓀴would try to come then when I did not want it; so, as I feared to be
5792𓀴alone, I opened my door and called out: “Is there anybody there?” There
5793𓀴was no answer. I was afraid to wake mother, and so closed my door again.
5794𓀴Then outside in the shrubbery I heard a sort of howl like a dog’s, but
5795𓀴more fierce and deeper. I went to the window and looked out, but could
5796𓀴see nothing, except a big bat, which had evidently been buffeting its
5797𓀴wings against the window. So I went back to bed again, but determined
5798𓀴not to go to sleep. Presently the door opened, and mother looked in;
5799𓀴seeing by my moving that I was not asleep, came in, and sat by me. She
5800𓀴said to me even more sweetly and softly than her wont:--
5801𓀴
5802𓀴“I was uneasy about you, darling, and came in to see that you were all
5803𓀴right.”
5804𓀴
5805𓀴I feared she might catch cold sitting there, and asked her to come in
5806𓀴and sleep with me, so she came into bed, and lay down beside me; she did
5807𓀴not take off her dressing gown, for she said she would only stay a while
5808𓀴and then go back to her own bed. As she lay there in my arms, and I in
5809𓀴hers, the flapping and buffeting came to the window again. She was
5810𓀴startled and a little frightened, and cried out: “What is that?” I tried
5811𓀴to pacify her, and at last succeeded, and she lay quiet; but I could
5812𓀴hear her poor dear heart still beating terribly. After a while there was
5813𓀴the low howl again out in the shrubbery, and shortly after there was a
5814𓀴crash at the window, and a lot of broken glass was hurled on the floor.
5815𓀴The window blind blew back with the wind that rushed in, and in the
5816𓀴aperture of the broken panes there was the head of a great, gaunt grey
5817𓀴wolf. Mother cried out in a fright, and struggled up into a sitting
5818𓀴posture, and clutched wildly at anything that would help her. Amongst
5819𓀴other things, she clutched the wreath of flowers that Dr. Van Helsing
5820𓀴insisted on my wearing round my neck, and tore it away from me. For a
5821𓀴second or two she sat up, pointing at the wolf, and there was a strange
5822𓀴and horrible gurgling in her throat; then she fell over--as if struck
5823𓀴with lightning, and her head hit my forehead and made me dizzy for a
5824𓀴moment or two. The room and all round seemed to spin round. I kept my
5825𓀴eyes fixed on the window, but the wolf drew his head back, and a whole
5826𓀴myriad of little specks seemed to come blowing in through the broken
5827𓀴window, and wheeling and circling round like the pillar of dust that
5828𓀴travellers describe when there is a simoon in the desert. I tried to
5829𓀴stir, but there was some spell upon me, and dear mother’s poor body,
5830𓀴which seemed to grow cold already--for her dear heart had ceased to
5831𓀴beat--weighed me down; and I remembered no more for a while.
5832𓀴
5833𓀴The time did not seem long, but very, very awful, till I recovered
5834𓀴consciousness again. Somewhere near, a passing bell was tolling; the
5835𓀴dogs all round the neighbourhood were howling; and in our shrubbery,
5836𓀴seemingly just outside, a nightingale was singing. I was dazed and
5837𓀴stupid with pain and terror and weakness, but the sound of the
5838𓀴nightingale seemed like the voice of my dead mother come back to comfort
5839𓀴me. The sounds seemed to have awakened the maids, too, for I could hear
5840𓀴their bare feet pattering outside my door. I called to them, and they
5841𓀴came in, and when they saw what had happened, and what it was that lay
5842𓀴over me on the bed, they screamed out. The wind rushed in through the
5843𓀴broken window, and the door slammed to. They lifted off the body of my
5844𓀴dear mother, and laid her, covered up with a sheet, on the bed after I
5845𓀴had got up. They were all so frightened and nervous that I directed them
5846𓀴to go to the dining-room and have each a glass of wine. The door flew
5847𓀴open for an instant and closed again. The maids shrieked, and then went
5848𓀴in a body to the dining-room; and I laid what flowers I had on my dear
5849𓀴mother’s breast. When they were there I remembered what Dr. Van Helsing
5850𓀴had told me, but I didn’t like to remove them, and, besides, I would
5851𓀴have some of the servants to sit up with me now. I was surprised that
5852𓀴the maids did not come back. I called them, but got no answer, so I went
5853𓀴to the dining-room to look for them.
5854𓀴
5855𓀴My heart sank when I saw what had happened. They all four lay helpless
5856𓀴on the floor, breathing heavily. The decanter of sherry was on the table
5857𓀴half full, but there was a queer, acrid smell about. I was suspicious,
5858𓀴and examined the decanter. It smelt of laudanum, and looking on the
5859𓀴sideboard, I found that the bottle which mother’s doctor uses for
5860𓀴her--oh! did use--was empty. What am I to do? what am I to do? I am back
5861𓀴in the room with mother. I cannot leave her, and I am alone, save for
5862𓀴the sleeping servants, whom some one has drugged. Alone with the dead! I
5863𓀴dare not go out, for I can hear the low howl of the wolf through the
5864𓀴broken window.
5865𓀴
5866𓀴The air seems full of specks, floating and circling in the draught from
5867𓀴the window, and the lights burn blue and dim. What am I to do? God
5868𓀴shield me from harm this night! I shall hide this paper in my breast,
5869𓀴where they shall find it when they come to lay me out. My dear mother
5870𓀴gone! It is time that I go too. Good-bye, dear Arthur, if I should not
5871𓀴survive this night. God keep you, dear, and God help me!
5872𓀴
5873𓀴
5874𓀴
5875𓀴
5876𓀴CHAPTER XII
5877𓀴
5878𓀴DR. SEWARD’S DIARY
5879𓀴
5880𓀴
5881𓀴_18 September._--I drove at once to Hillingham and arrived early.
5882𓀴Keeping my cab at the gate, I went up the avenue alone. I knocked gently
5883𓀴and rang as quietly as possible, for I feared to disturb Lucy or her
5884𓀴mother, and hoped to only bring a servant to the door. After a while,
5885𓀴finding no response, I knocked and rang again; still no answer. I cursed
5886𓀴the laziness of the servants that they should lie abed at such an
5887𓀴hour--for it was now ten o’clock--and so rang and knocked again, but
5888𓀴more impatiently, but still without response. Hitherto I had blamed only
5889𓀴the servants, but now a terrible fear began to assail me. Was this
5890𓀴desolation but another link in the chain of doom which seemed drawing
5891𓀴tight around us? Was it indeed a house of death to which I had come, too
5892𓀴late? I knew that minutes, even seconds of delay, might mean hours of
5893𓀴danger to Lucy, if she had had again one of those frightful relapses;
5894𓀴and I went round the house to try if I could find by chance an entry
5895𓀴anywhere.
5896𓀴
5897𓀴I could find no means of ingress. Every window and door was fastened and
5898𓀴locked, and I returned baffled to the porch. As I did so, I heard the
5899𓀴rapid pit-pat of a swiftly driven horse’s feet. They stopped at the
5900𓀴gate, and a few seconds later I met Van Helsing running up the avenue.
5901𓀴When he saw me, he gasped out:--
5902𓀴
5903𓀴“Then it was you, and just arrived. How is she? Are we too late? Did you
5904𓀴not get my telegram?”
5905𓀴
5906𓀴I answered as quickly and coherently as I could that I had only got his
5907𓀴telegram early in the morning, and had not lost a minute in coming here,
5908𓀴and that I could not make any one in the house hear me. He paused and
5909𓀴raised his hat as he said solemnly:--
5910𓀴
5911𓀴“Then I fear we are too late. God’s will be done!” With his usual
5912𓀴recuperative energy, he went on: “Come. If there be no way open to get
5913𓀴in, we must make one. Time is all in all to us now.”
5914𓀴
5915𓀴We went round to the back of the house, where there was a kitchen
5916𓀴window. The Professor took a small surgical saw from his case, and
5917𓀴handing it to me, pointed to the iron bars which guarded the window. I
5918𓀴attacked them at once and had very soon cut through three of them. Then
5919𓀴with a long, thin knife we pushed back the fastening of the sashes and
5920𓀴opened the window. I helped the Professor in, and followed him. There
5921𓀴was no one in the kitchen or in the servants’ rooms, which were close at
5922𓀴hand. We tried all the rooms as we went along, and in the dining-room,
5923𓀴dimly lit by rays of light through the shutters, found four
5924𓀴servant-women lying on the floor. There was no need to think them dead,
5925𓀴for their stertorous breathing and the acrid smell of laudanum in the
5926𓀴room left no doubt as to their condition. Van Helsing and I looked at
5927𓀴each other, and as we moved away he said: “We can attend to them later.”
5928𓀴Then we ascended to Lucy’s room. For an instant or two we paused at the
5929𓀴door to listen, but there was no sound that we could hear. With white
5930𓀴faces and trembling hands, we opened the door gently, and entered the
5931𓀴room.
5932𓀴
5933𓀴How shall I describe what we saw? On the bed lay two women, Lucy and her
5934𓀴mother. The latter lay farthest in, and she was covered with a white
5935𓀴sheet, the edge of which had been blown back by the draught through the
5936𓀴broken window, showing the drawn, white face, with a look of terror
5937𓀴fixed upon it. By her side lay Lucy, with face white and still more
5938𓀴drawn. The flowers which had been round her neck we found upon her
5939𓀴mother’s bosom, and her throat was bare, showing the two little wounds
5940𓀴which we had noticed before, but looking horribly white and mangled.
5941𓀴Without a word the Professor bent over the bed, his head almost touching
5942𓀴poor Lucy’s breast; then he gave a quick turn of his head, as of one who
5943𓀴listens, and leaping to his feet, he cried out to me:--
5944𓀴
5945𓀴“It is not yet too late! Quick! quick! Bring the brandy!”
5946𓀴
5947𓀴I flew downstairs and returned with it, taking care to smell and taste
5948𓀴it, lest it, too, were drugged like the decanter of sherry which I found
5949𓀴on the table. The maids were still breathing, but more restlessly, and I
5950𓀴fancied that the narcotic was wearing off. I did not stay to make sure,
5951𓀴but returned to Van Helsing. He rubbed the brandy, as on another
5952𓀴occasion, on her lips and gums and on her wrists and the palms of her
5953𓀴hands. He said to me:--
5954𓀴
5955𓀴“I can do this, all that can be at the present. You go wake those maids.
5956𓀴Flick them in the face with a wet towel, and flick them hard. Make them
5957𓀴get heat and fire and a warm bath. This poor soul is nearly as cold as
5958𓀴that beside her. She will need be heated before we can do anything
5959𓀴more.”
5960𓀴
5961𓀴I went at once, and found little difficulty in waking three of the
5962𓀴women. The fourth was only a young girl, and the drug had evidently
5963𓀴affected her more strongly, so I lifted her on the sofa and let her
5964𓀴sleep. The others were dazed at first, but as remembrance came back to
5965𓀴them they cried and sobbed in a hysterical manner. I was stern with
5966𓀴them, however, and would not let them talk. I told them that one life
5967𓀴was bad enough to lose, and that if they delayed they would sacrifice
5968𓀴Miss Lucy. So, sobbing and crying, they went about their way, half clad
5969𓀴as they were, and prepared fire and water. Fortunately, the kitchen and
5970𓀴boiler fires were still alive, and there was no lack of hot water. We
5971𓀴got a bath and carried Lucy out as she was and placed her in it. Whilst
5972𓀴we were busy chafing her limbs there was a knock at the hall door. One
5973𓀴of the maids ran off, hurried on some more clothes, and opened it. Then
5974𓀴she returned and whispered to us that there was a gentleman who had come
5975𓀴with a message from Mr. Holmwood. I bade her simply tell him that he
5976𓀴must wait, for we could see no one now. She went away with the message,
5977𓀴and, engrossed with our work, I clean forgot all about him.
5978𓀴
5979𓀴I never saw in all my experience the Professor work in such deadly
5980𓀴earnest. I knew--as he knew--that it was a stand-up fight with death,
5981𓀴and in a pause told him so. He answered me in a way that I did not
5982𓀴understand, but with the sternest look that his face could wear:--
5983𓀴
5984𓀴“If that were all, I would stop here where we are now, and let her fade
5985𓀴away into peace, for I see no light in life over her horizon.” He went
5986𓀴on with his work with, if possible, renewed and more frenzied vigour.
5987𓀴
5988𓀴Presently we both began to be conscious that the heat was beginning to
5989𓀴be of some effect. Lucy’s heart beat a trifle more audibly to the
5990𓀴stethoscope, and her lungs had a perceptible movement. Van Helsing’s
5991𓀴face almost beamed, and as we lifted her from the bath and rolled her in
5992𓀴a hot sheet to dry her he said to me:--
5993𓀴
5994𓀴“The first gain is ours! Check to the King!”
5995𓀴
5996𓀴We took Lucy into another room, which had by now been prepared, and laid
5997𓀴her in bed and forced a few drops of brandy down her throat. I noticed
5998𓀴that Van Helsing tied a soft silk handkerchief round her throat. She was
5999𓀴still unconscious, and was quite as bad as, if not worse than, we had
6000𓀴ever seen her.
6001𓀴
6002𓀴Van Helsing called in one of the women, and told her to stay with her
6003𓀴and not to take her eyes off her till we returned, and then beckoned me
6004𓀴out of the room.
6005𓀴
6006𓀴“We must consult as to what is to be done,” he said as we descended the
6007𓀴stairs. In the hall he opened the dining-room door, and we passed in, he
6008𓀴closing the door carefully behind him. The shutters had been opened, but
6009𓀴the blinds were already down, with that obedience to the etiquette of
6010𓀴death which the British woman of the lower classes always rigidly
6011𓀴observes. The room was, therefore, dimly dark. It was, however, light
6012𓀴enough for our purposes. Van Helsing’s sternness was somewhat relieved
6013𓀴by a look of perplexity. He was evidently torturing his mind about
6014𓀴something, so I waited for an instant, and he spoke:--
6015𓀴
6016𓀴“What are we to do now? Where are we to turn for help? We must have
6017𓀴another transfusion of blood, and that soon, or that poor girl’s life
6018𓀴won’t be worth an hour’s purchase. You are exhausted already; I am
6019𓀴exhausted too. I fear to trust those women, even if they would have
6020𓀴courage to submit. What are we to do for some one who will open his
6021𓀴veins for her?”
6022𓀴
6023𓀴“What’s the matter with me, anyhow?”
6024𓀴
6025𓀴The voice came from the sofa across the room, and its tones brought
6026𓀴relief and joy to my heart, for they were those of Quincey Morris. Van
6027𓀴Helsing started angrily at the first sound, but his face softened and a
6028𓀴glad look came into his eyes as I cried out: “Quincey Morris!” and
6029𓀴rushed towards him with outstretched hands.
6030𓀴
6031𓀴“What brought you here?” I cried as our hands met.
6032𓀴
6033𓀴“I guess Art is the cause.”
6034𓀴
6035𓀴He handed me a telegram:--
6036𓀴
6037𓀴“Have not heard from Seward for three days, and am terribly anxious.
6038𓀴Cannot leave. Father still in same condition. Send me word how Lucy is.
6039𓀴Do not delay.--HOLMWOOD.”
6040𓀴
6041𓀴“I think I came just in the nick of time. You know you have only to tell
6042𓀴me what to do.”
6043𓀴
6044𓀴Van Helsing strode forward, and took his hand, looking him straight in
6045𓀴the eyes as he said:--
6046𓀴
6047𓀴“A brave man’s blood is the best thing on this earth when a woman is in
6048𓀴trouble. You’re a man and no mistake. Well, the devil may work against
6049𓀴us for all he’s worth, but God sends us men when we want them.”
6050𓀴
6051𓀴Once again we went through that ghastly operation. I have not the heart
6052𓀴to go through with the details. Lucy had got a terrible shock and it
6053𓀴told on her more than before, for though plenty of blood went into her
6054𓀴veins, her body did not respond to the treatment as well as on the other
6055𓀴occasions. Her struggle back into life was something frightful to see
6056𓀴and hear. However, the action of both heart and lungs improved, and Van
6057𓀴Helsing made a subcutaneous injection of morphia, as before, and with
6058𓀴good effect. Her faint became a profound slumber. The Professor watched
6059𓀴whilst I went downstairs with Quincey Morris, and sent one of the maids
6060𓀴to pay off one of the cabmen who were waiting. I left Quincey lying down
6061𓀴after having a glass of wine, and told the cook to get ready a good
6062𓀴breakfast. Then a thought struck me, and I went back to the room where
6063𓀴Lucy now was. When I came softly in, I found Van Helsing with a sheet or
6064𓀴two of note-paper in his hand. He had evidently read it, and was
6065𓀴thinking it over as he sat with his hand to his brow. There was a look
6066𓀴of grim satisfaction in his face, as of one who has had a doubt solved.
6067𓀴He handed me the paper saying only: “It dropped from Lucy’s breast when
6068𓀴we carried her to the bath.”
6069𓀴
6070𓀴When I had read it, I stood looking at the Professor, and after a pause
6071𓀴asked him: “In God’s name, what does it all mean? Was she, or is she,
6072𓀴mad; or what sort of horrible danger is it?” I was so bewildered that I
6073𓀴did not know what to say more. Van Helsing put out his hand and took the
6074𓀴paper, saying:--
6075𓀴
6076𓀴“Do not trouble about it now. Forget it for the present. You shall know
6077𓀴and understand it all in good time; but it will be later. And now what
6078𓀴is it that you came to me to say?” This brought me back to fact, and I
6079𓀴was all myself again.
6080𓀴
6081𓀴“I came to speak about the certificate of death. If we do not act
6082𓀴properly and wisely, there may be an inquest, and that paper would have
6083𓀴to be produced. I am in hopes that we need have no inquest, for if we
6084𓀴had it would surely kill poor Lucy, if nothing else did. I know, and you
6085𓀴know, and the other doctor who attended her knows, that Mrs. Westenra
6086𓀴had disease of the heart, and we can certify that she died of it. Let us
6087𓀴fill up the certificate at once, and I shall take it myself to the
6088𓀴registrar and go on to the undertaker.”
6089𓀴
6090𓀴“Good, oh my friend John! Well thought of! Truly Miss Lucy, if she be
6091𓀴sad in the foes that beset her, is at least happy in the friends that
6092𓀴love her. One, two, three, all open their veins for her, besides one old
6093𓀴man. Ah yes, I know, friend John; I am not blind! I love you all the
6094𓀴more for it! Now go.”
6095𓀴
6096𓀴In the hall I met Quincey Morris, with a telegram for Arthur telling him
6097𓀴that Mrs. Westenra was dead; that Lucy also had been ill, but was now
6098𓀴going on better; and that Van Helsing and I were with her. I told him
6099𓀴where I was going, and he hurried me out, but as I was going said:--
6100𓀴
6101𓀴“When you come back, Jack, may I have two words with you all to
6102𓀴ourselves?” I nodded in reply and went out. I found no difficulty about
6103𓀴the registration, and arranged with the local undertaker to come up in
6104𓀴the evening to measure for the coffin and to make arrangements.
6105𓀴
6106𓀴When I got back Quincey was waiting for me. I told him I would see him
6107𓀴as soon as I knew about Lucy, and went up to her room. She was still
6108𓀴sleeping, and the Professor seemingly had not moved from his seat at her
6109𓀴side. From his putting his finger to his lips, I gathered that he
6110𓀴expected her to wake before long and was afraid of forestalling nature.
6111𓀴So I went down to Quincey and took him into the breakfast-room, where
6112𓀴the blinds were not drawn down, and which was a little more cheerful, or
6113𓀴rather less cheerless, than the other rooms. When we were alone, he said
6114𓀴to me:--
6115𓀴
6116𓀴“Jack Seward, I don’t want to shove myself in anywhere where I’ve no
6117𓀴right to be; but this is no ordinary case. You know I loved that girl
6118𓀴and wanted to marry her; but, although that’s all past and gone, I can’t
6119𓀴help feeling anxious about her all the same. What is it that’s wrong
6120𓀴with her? The Dutchman--and a fine old fellow he is; I can see
6121𓀴that--said, that time you two came into the room, that you must have
6122𓀴_another_ transfusion of blood, and that both you and he were exhausted.
6123𓀴Now I know well that you medical men speak _in camera_, and that a man
6124𓀴must not expect to know what they consult about in private. But this is
6125𓀴no common matter, and, whatever it is, I have done my part. Is not that
6126𓀴so?”
6127𓀴
6128𓀴“That’s so,” I said, and he went on:--
6129𓀴
6130𓀴“I take it that both you and Van Helsing had done already what I did
6131𓀴to-day. Is not that so?”
6132𓀴
6133𓀴“That’s so.”
6134𓀴
6135𓀴“And I guess Art was in it too. When I saw him four days ago down at his
6136𓀴own place he looked queer. I have not seen anything pulled down so quick
6137𓀴since I was on the Pampas and had a mare that I was fond of go to grass
6138𓀴all in a night. One of those big bats that they call vampires had got at
6139𓀴her in the night, and what with his gorge and the vein left open, there
6140𓀴wasn’t enough blood in her to let her stand up, and I had to put a
6141𓀴bullet through her as she lay. Jack, if you may tell me without
6142𓀴betraying confidence, Arthur was the first, is not that so?” As he spoke
6143𓀴the poor fellow looked terribly anxious. He was in a torture of suspense
6144𓀴regarding the woman he loved, and his utter ignorance of the terrible
6145𓀴mystery which seemed to surround her intensified his pain. His very
6146𓀴heart was bleeding, and it took all the manhood of him--and there was a
6147𓀴royal lot of it, too--to keep him from breaking down. I paused before
6148𓀴answering, for I felt that I must not betray anything which the
6149𓀴Professor wished kept secret; but already he knew so much, and guessed
6150𓀴so much, that there could be no reason for not answering, so I answered
6151𓀴in the same phrase: “That’s so.”
6152𓀴
6153𓀴“And how long has this been going on?”
6154𓀴
6155𓀴“About ten days.”
6156𓀴
6157𓀴“Ten days! Then I guess, Jack Seward, that that poor pretty creature
6158𓀴that we all love has had put into her veins within that time the blood
6159𓀴of four strong men. Man alive, her whole body wouldn’t hold it.” Then,
6160𓀴coming close to me, he spoke in a fierce half-whisper: “What took it
6161𓀴out?”
6162𓀴
6163𓀴I shook my head. “That,” I said, “is the crux. Van Helsing is simply
6164𓀴frantic about it, and I am at my wits’ end. I can’t even hazard a guess.
6165𓀴There has been a series of little circumstances which have thrown out
6166𓀴all our calculations as to Lucy being properly watched. But these shall
6167𓀴not occur again. Here we stay until all be well--or ill.” Quincey held
6168𓀴out his hand. “Count me in,” he said. “You and the Dutchman will tell me
6169𓀴what to do, and I’ll do it.”
6170𓀴
6171𓀴When she woke late in the afternoon, Lucy’s first movement was to feel
6172𓀴in her breast, and, to my surprise, produced the paper which Van Helsing
6173𓀴had given me to read. The careful Professor had replaced it where it had
6174𓀴come from, lest on waking she should be alarmed. Her eye then lit on Van
6175𓀴Helsing and on me too, and gladdened. Then she looked around the room,
6176𓀴and seeing where she was, shuddered; she gave a loud cry, and put her
6177𓀴poor thin hands before her pale face. We both understood what that
6178𓀴meant--that she had realised to the full her mother’s death; so we tried
6179𓀴what we could to comfort her. Doubtless sympathy eased her somewhat, but
6180𓀴she was very low in thought and spirit, and wept silently and weakly for
6181𓀴a long time. We told her that either or both of us would now remain with
6182𓀴her all the time, and that seemed to comfort her. Towards dusk she fell
6183𓀴into a doze. Here a very odd thing occurred. Whilst still asleep she
6184𓀴took the paper from her breast and tore it in two. Van Helsing stepped
6185𓀴over and took the pieces from her. All the same, however, she went on
6186𓀴with the action of tearing, as though the material were still in her
6187𓀴hands; finally she lifted her hands and opened them as though scattering
6188𓀴the fragments. Van Helsing seemed surprised, and his brows gathered as
6189𓀴if in thought, but he said nothing.
6190𓀴
6191𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
6192𓀴
6193𓀴_19 September._--All last night she slept fitfully, being always afraid
6194𓀴to sleep, and something weaker when she woke from it. The Professor and
6195𓀴I took it in turns to watch, and we never left her for a moment
6196𓀴unattended. Quincey Morris said nothing about his intention, but I knew
6197𓀴that all night long he patrolled round and round the house.
6198𓀴
6199𓀴When the day came, its searching light showed the ravages in poor Lucy’s
6200𓀴strength. She was hardly able to turn her head, and the little
6201𓀴nourishment which she could take seemed to do her no good. At times she
6202𓀴slept, and both Van Helsing and I noticed the difference in her, between
6203𓀴sleeping and waking. Whilst asleep she looked stronger, although more
6204𓀴haggard, and her breathing was softer; her open mouth showed the pale
6205𓀴gums drawn back from the teeth, which thus looked positively longer and
6206𓀴sharper than usual; when she woke the softness of her eyes evidently
6207𓀴changed the expression, for she looked her own self, although a dying
6208𓀴one. In the afternoon she asked for Arthur, and we telegraphed for him.
6209𓀴Quincey went off to meet him at the station.
6210𓀴
6211𓀴When he arrived it was nearly six o’clock, and the sun was setting full
6212𓀴and warm, and the red light streamed in through the window and gave more
6213𓀴colour to the pale cheeks. When he saw her, Arthur was simply choking
6214𓀴with emotion, and none of us could speak. In the hours that had passed,
6215𓀴the fits of sleep, or the comatose condition that passed for it, had
6216𓀴grown more frequent, so that the pauses when conversation was possible
6217𓀴were shortened. Arthur’s presence, however, seemed to act as a
6218𓀴stimulant; she rallied a little, and spoke to him more brightly than she
6219𓀴had done since we arrived. He too pulled himself together, and spoke as
6220𓀴cheerily as he could, so that the best was made of everything.
6221𓀴
6222𓀴It was now nearly one o’clock, and he and Van Helsing are sitting with
6223𓀴her. I am to relieve them in a quarter of an hour, and I am entering
6224𓀴this on Lucy’s phonograph. Until six o’clock they are to try to rest. I
6225𓀴fear that to-morrow will end our watching, for the shock has been too
6226𓀴great; the poor child cannot rally. God help us all.
6227𓀴
6228𓀴
6229𓀴_Letter, Mina Harker to Lucy Westenra._
6230𓀴
6231𓀴(Unopened by her.)
6232𓀴
6233𓀴“_17 September._
6234𓀴
6235𓀴“My dearest Lucy,--
6236𓀴
6237𓀴“It seems _an age_ since I heard from you, or indeed since I wrote. You
6238𓀴will pardon me, I know, for all my faults when you have read all my
6239𓀴budget of news. Well, I got my husband back all right; when we arrived
6240𓀴at Exeter there was a carriage waiting for us, and in it, though he had
6241𓀴an attack of gout, Mr. Hawkins. He took us to his house, where there
6242𓀴were rooms for us all nice and comfortable, and we dined together. After
6243𓀴dinner Mr. Hawkins said:--
6244𓀴
6245𓀴“‘My dears, I want to drink your health and prosperity; and may every
6246𓀴blessing attend you both. I know you both from children, and have, with
6247𓀴love and pride, seen you grow up. Now I want you to make your home here
6248𓀴with me. I have left to me neither chick nor child; all are gone, and in
6249𓀴my will I have left you everything.’ I cried, Lucy dear, as Jonathan and
6250𓀴the old man clasped hands. Our evening was a very, very happy one.
6251𓀴
6252𓀴“So here we are, installed in this beautiful old house, and from both my
6253𓀴bedroom and the drawing-room I can see the great elms of the cathedral
6254𓀴close, with their great black stems standing out against the old yellow
6255𓀴stone of the cathedral and I can hear the rooks overhead cawing and
6256𓀴cawing and chattering and gossiping all day, after the manner of
6257𓀴rooks--and humans. I am busy, I need not tell you, arranging things and
6258𓀴housekeeping. Jonathan and Mr. Hawkins are busy all day; for, now that
6259𓀴Jonathan is a partner, Mr. Hawkins wants to tell him all about the
6260𓀴clients.
6261𓀴
6262𓀴“How is your dear mother getting on? I wish I could run up to town for a
6263𓀴day or two to see you, dear, but I dare not go yet, with so much on my
6264𓀴shoulders; and Jonathan wants looking after still. He is beginning to
6265𓀴put some flesh on his bones again, but he was terribly weakened by the
6266𓀴long illness; even now he sometimes starts out of his sleep in a sudden
6267𓀴way and awakes all trembling until I can coax him back to his usual
6268𓀴placidity. However, thank God, these occasions grow less frequent as the
6269𓀴days go on, and they will in time pass away altogether, I trust. And now
6270𓀴I have told you my news, let me ask yours. When are you to be married,
6271𓀴and where, and who is to perform the ceremony, and what are you to wear,
6272𓀴and is it to be a public or a private wedding? Tell me all about it,
6273𓀴dear; tell me all about everything, for there is nothing which interests
6274𓀴you which will not be dear to me. Jonathan asks me to send his
6275𓀴‘respectful duty,’ but I do not think that is good enough from the
6276𓀴junior partner of the important firm Hawkins & Harker; and so, as you
6277𓀴love me, and he loves me, and I love you with all the moods and tenses
6278𓀴of the verb, I send you simply his ‘love’ instead. Good-bye, my dearest
6279𓀴Lucy, and all blessings on you.
6280𓀴
6281𓀴“Yours,
6282𓀴
6283𓀴“MINA HARKER.”
6284𓀴
6285𓀴
6286𓀴_Report from Patrick Hennessey, M. D., M. R. C. S. L. K. Q. C. P. I.,
6287𓀴etc., etc., to John Seward, M. D._
6288𓀴
6289𓀴“_20 September._
6290𓀴
6291𓀴“My dear Sir,--
6292𓀴
6293𓀴“In accordance with your wishes, I enclose report of the conditions of
6294𓀴everything left in my charge.... With regard to patient, Renfield, there
6295𓀴is more to say. He has had another outbreak, which might have had a
6296𓀴dreadful ending, but which, as it fortunately happened, was unattended
6297𓀴with any unhappy results. This afternoon a carrier’s cart with two men
6298𓀴made a call at the empty house whose grounds abut on ours--the house to
6299𓀴which, you will remember, the patient twice ran away. The men stopped at
6300𓀴our gate to ask the porter their way, as they were strangers. I was
6301𓀴myself looking out of the study window, having a smoke after dinner, and
6302𓀴saw one of them come up to the house. As he passed the window of
6303𓀴Renfield’s room, the patient began to rate him from within, and called
6304𓀴him all the foul names he could lay his tongue to. The man, who seemed a
6305𓀴decent fellow enough, contented himself by telling him to “shut up for a
6306𓀴foul-mouthed beggar,” whereon our man accused him of robbing him and
6307𓀴wanting to murder him and said that he would hinder him if he were to
6308𓀴swing for it. I opened the window and signed to the man not to notice,
6309𓀴so he contented himself after looking the place over and making up his
6310𓀴mind as to what kind of a place he had got to by saying: ‘Lor’ bless
6311𓀴yer, sir, I wouldn’t mind what was said to me in a bloomin’ madhouse. I
6312𓀴pity ye and the guv’nor for havin’ to live in the house with a wild
6313𓀴beast like that.’ Then he asked his way civilly enough, and I told him
6314𓀴where the gate of the empty house was; he went away, followed by threats
6315𓀴and curses and revilings from our man. I went down to see if I could
6316𓀴make out any cause for his anger, since he is usually such a
6317𓀴well-behaved man, and except his violent fits nothing of the kind had
6318𓀴ever occurred. I found him, to my astonishment, quite composed and most
6319𓀴genial in his manner. I tried to get him to talk of the incident, but he
6320𓀴blandly asked me questions as to what I meant, and led me to believe
6321𓀴that he was completely oblivious of the affair. It was, I am sorry to
6322𓀴say, however, only another instance of his cunning, for within half an
6323𓀴hour I heard of him again. This time he had broken out through the
6324𓀴window of his room, and was running down the avenue. I called to the
6325𓀴attendants to follow me, and ran after him, for I feared he was intent
6326𓀴on some mischief. My fear was justified when I saw the same cart which
6327𓀴had passed before coming down the road, having on it some great wooden
6328𓀴boxes. The men were wiping their foreheads, and were flushed in the
6329𓀴face, as if with violent exercise. Before I could get up to him the
6330𓀴patient rushed at them, and pulling one of them off the cart, began to
6331𓀴knock his head against the ground. If I had not seized him just at the
6332𓀴moment I believe he would have killed the man there and then. The other
6333𓀴fellow jumped down and struck him over the head with the butt-end of his
6334𓀴heavy whip. It was a terrible blow; but he did not seem to mind it, but
6335𓀴seized him also, and struggled with the three of us, pulling us to and
6336𓀴fro as if we were kittens. You know I am no light weight, and the others
6337𓀴were both burly men. At first he was silent in his fighting; but as we
6338𓀴began to master him, and the attendants were putting a strait-waistcoat
6339𓀴on him, he began to shout: ‘I’ll frustrate them! They shan’t rob me!
6340𓀴they shan’t murder me by inches! I’ll fight for my Lord and Master!’ and
6341𓀴all sorts of similar incoherent ravings. It was with very considerable
6342𓀴difficulty that they got him back to the house and put him in the padded
6343𓀴room. One of the attendants, Hardy, had a finger broken. However, I set
6344𓀴it all right; and he is going on well.
6345𓀴
6346𓀴“The two carriers were at first loud in their threats of actions for
6347𓀴damages, and promised to rain all the penalties of the law on us. Their
6348𓀴threats were, however, mingled with some sort of indirect apology for
6349𓀴the defeat of the two of them by a feeble madman. They said that if it
6350𓀴had not been for the way their strength had been spent in carrying and
6351𓀴raising the heavy boxes to the cart they would have made short work of
6352𓀴him. They gave as another reason for their defeat the extraordinary
6353𓀴state of drouth to which they had been reduced by the dusty nature of
6354𓀴their occupation and the reprehensible distance from the scene of their
6355𓀴labours of any place of public entertainment. I quite understood their
6356𓀴drift, and after a stiff glass of grog, or rather more of the same, and
6357𓀴with each a sovereign in hand, they made light of the attack, and swore
6358𓀴that they would encounter a worse madman any day for the pleasure of
6359𓀴meeting so ‘bloomin’ good a bloke’ as your correspondent. I took their
6360𓀴names and addresses, in case they might be needed. They are as
6361𓀴follows:--Jack Smollet, of Dudding’s Rents, King George’s Road, Great
6362𓀴Walworth, and Thomas Snelling, Peter Farley’s Row, Guide Court, Bethnal
6363𓀴Green. They are both in the employment of Harris & Sons, Moving and
6364𓀴Shipment Company, Orange Master’s Yard, Soho.
6365𓀴
6366𓀴“I shall report to you any matter of interest occurring here, and shall
6367𓀴wire you at once if there is anything of importance.
6368𓀴
6369𓀴“Believe me, dear Sir,
6370𓀴
6371𓀴“Yours faithfully,
6372𓀴
6373𓀴“PATRICK HENNESSEY.”
6374𓀴
6375𓀴
6376𓀴_Letter, Mina Harker to Lucy Westenra_.
6377𓀴
6378𓀴(Unopened by her.)
6379𓀴
6380𓀴“_18 September._
6381𓀴
6382𓀴“My dearest Lucy,--
6383𓀴
6384𓀴“Such a sad blow has befallen us. Mr. Hawkins has died very suddenly.
6385𓀴Some may not think it so sad for us, but we had both come to so love him
6386𓀴that it really seems as though we had lost a father. I never knew either
6387𓀴father or mother, so that the dear old man’s death is a real blow to me.
6388𓀴Jonathan is greatly distressed. It is not only that he feels sorrow,
6389𓀴deep sorrow, for the dear, good man who has befriended him all his life,
6390𓀴and now at the end has treated him like his own son and left him a
6391𓀴fortune which to people of our modest bringing up is wealth beyond the
6392𓀴dream of avarice, but Jonathan feels it on another account. He says the
6393𓀴amount of responsibility which it puts upon him makes him nervous. He
6394𓀴begins to doubt himself. I try to cheer him up, and _my_ belief in _him_
6395𓀴helps him to have a belief in himself. But it is here that the grave
6396𓀴shock that he experienced tells upon him the most. Oh, it is too hard
6397𓀴that a sweet, simple, noble, strong nature such as his--a nature which
6398𓀴enabled him by our dear, good friend’s aid to rise from clerk to master
6399𓀴in a few years--should be so injured that the very essence of its
6400𓀴strength is gone. Forgive me, dear, if I worry you with my troubles in
6401𓀴the midst of your own happiness; but, Lucy dear, I must tell some one,
6402𓀴for the strain of keeping up a brave and cheerful appearance to Jonathan
6403𓀴tries me, and I have no one here that I can confide in. I dread coming
6404𓀴up to London, as we must do the day after to-morrow; for poor Mr.
6405𓀴Hawkins left in his will that he was to be buried in the grave with his
6406𓀴father. As there are no relations at all, Jonathan will have to be chief
6407𓀴mourner. I shall try to run over to see you, dearest, if only for a few
6408𓀴minutes. Forgive me for troubling you. With all blessings,
6409𓀴
6410𓀴“Your loving
6411𓀴
6412𓀴“MINA HARKER.”
6413𓀴
6414𓀴
6415𓀴_Dr. Seward’s Diary._
6416𓀴
6417𓀴_20 September._--Only resolution and habit can let me make an entry
6418𓀴to-night. I am too miserable, too low-spirited, too sick of the world
6419𓀴and all in it, including life itself, that I would not care if I heard
6420𓀴this moment the flapping of the wings of the angel of death. And he has
6421𓀴been flapping those grim wings to some purpose of late--Lucy’s mother
6422𓀴and Arthur’s father, and now.... Let me get on with my work.
6423𓀴
6424𓀴I duly relieved Van Helsing in his watch over Lucy. We wanted Arthur to
6425𓀴go to rest also, but he refused at first. It was only when I told him
6426𓀴that we should want him to help us during the day, and that we must not
6427𓀴all break down for want of rest, lest Lucy should suffer, that he agreed
6428𓀴to go. Van Helsing was very kind to him. “Come, my child,” he said;
6429𓀴“come with me. You are sick and weak, and have had much sorrow and much
6430𓀴mental pain, as well as that tax on your strength that we know of. You
6431𓀴must not be alone; for to be alone is to be full of fears and alarms.
6432𓀴Come to the drawing-room, where there is a big fire, and there are two
6433𓀴sofas. You shall lie on one, and I on the other, and our sympathy will
6434𓀴be comfort to each other, even though we do not speak, and even if we
6435𓀴sleep.” Arthur went off with him, casting back a longing look on Lucy’s
6436𓀴face, which lay in her pillow, almost whiter than the lawn. She lay
6437𓀴quite still, and I looked round the room to see that all was as it
6438𓀴should be. I could see that the Professor had carried out in this room,
6439𓀴as in the other, his purpose of using the garlic; the whole of the
6440𓀴window-sashes reeked with it, and round Lucy’s neck, over the silk
6441𓀴handkerchief which Van Helsing made her keep on, was a rough chaplet of
6442𓀴the same odorous flowers. Lucy was breathing somewhat stertorously, and
6443𓀴her face was at its worst, for the open mouth showed the pale gums. Her
6444𓀴teeth, in the dim, uncertain light, seemed longer and sharper than they
6445𓀴had been in the morning. In particular, by some trick of the light, the
6446𓀴canine teeth looked longer and sharper than the rest. I sat down by her,
6447𓀴and presently she moved uneasily. At the same moment there came a sort
6448𓀴of dull flapping or buffeting at the window. I went over to it softly,
6449𓀴and peeped out by the corner of the blind. There was a full moonlight,
6450𓀴and I could see that the noise was made by a great bat, which wheeled
6451𓀴round--doubtless attracted by the light, although so dim--and every now
6452𓀴and again struck the window with its wings. When I came back to my seat,
6453𓀴I found that Lucy had moved slightly, and had torn away the garlic
6454𓀴flowers from her throat. I replaced them as well as I could, and sat
6455𓀴watching her.
6456𓀴
6457𓀴Presently she woke, and I gave her food, as Van Helsing had prescribed.
6458𓀴She took but a little, and that languidly. There did not seem to be with
6459𓀴her now the unconscious struggle for life and strength that had hitherto
6460𓀴so marked her illness. It struck me as curious that the moment she
6461𓀴became conscious she pressed the garlic flowers close to her. It was
6462𓀴certainly odd that whenever she got into that lethargic state, with the
6463𓀴stertorous breathing, she put the flowers from her; but that when she
6464𓀴waked she clutched them close. There was no possibility of making any
6465𓀴mistake about this, for in the long hours that followed, she had many
6466𓀴spells of sleeping and waking and repeated both actions many times.
6467𓀴
6468𓀴At six o’clock Van Helsing came to relieve me. Arthur had then fallen
6469𓀴into a doze, and he mercifully let him sleep on. When he saw Lucy’s face
6470𓀴I could hear the sissing indraw of his breath, and he said to me in a
6471𓀴sharp whisper: “Draw up the blind; I want light!” Then he bent down,
6472𓀴and, with his face almost touching Lucy’s, examined her carefully. He
6473𓀴removed the flowers and lifted the silk handkerchief from her throat. As
6474𓀴he did so he started back, and I could hear his ejaculation, “Mein
6475𓀴Gott!” as it was smothered in his throat. I bent over and looked, too,
6476𓀴and as I noticed some queer chill came over me.
6477𓀴
6478𓀴The wounds on the throat had absolutely disappeared.
6479𓀴
6480𓀴For fully five minutes Van Helsing stood looking at her, with his face
6481𓀴at its sternest. Then he turned to me and said calmly:--
6482𓀴
6483𓀴“She is dying. It will not be long now. It will be much difference, mark
6484𓀴me, whether she dies conscious or in her sleep. Wake that poor boy, and
6485𓀴let him come and see the last; he trusts us, and we have promised him.”
6486𓀴
6487𓀴I went to the dining-room and waked him. He was dazed for a moment, but
6488𓀴when he saw the sunlight streaming in through the edges of the shutters
6489𓀴he thought he was late, and expressed his fear. I assured him that Lucy
6490𓀴was still asleep, but told him as gently as I could that both Van
6491𓀴Helsing and I feared that the end was near. He covered his face with his
6492𓀴hands, and slid down on his knees by the sofa, where he remained,
6493𓀴perhaps a minute, with his head buried, praying, whilst his shoulders
6494𓀴shook with grief. I took him by the hand and raised him up. “Come,” I
6495𓀴said, “my dear old fellow, summon all your fortitude: it will be best
6496𓀴and easiest for her.”
6497𓀴
6498𓀴When we came into Lucy’s room I could see that Van Helsing had, with
6499𓀴his usual forethought, been putting matters straight and making
6500𓀴everything look as pleasing as possible. He had even brushed Lucy’s
6501𓀴hair, so that it lay on the pillow in its usual sunny ripples. When we
6502𓀴came into the room she opened her eyes, and seeing him, whispered
6503𓀴softly:--
6504𓀴
6505𓀴“Arthur! Oh, my love, I am so glad you have come!” He was stooping to
6506𓀴kiss her, when Van Helsing motioned him back. “No,” he whispered, “not
6507𓀴yet! Hold her hand; it will comfort her more.”
6508𓀴
6509𓀴So Arthur took her hand and knelt beside her, and she looked her best,
6510𓀴with all the soft lines matching the angelic beauty of her eyes. Then
6511𓀴gradually her eyes closed, and she sank to sleep. For a little bit her
6512𓀴breast heaved softly, and her breath came and went like a tired child’s.
6513𓀴
6514𓀴And then insensibly there came the strange change which I had noticed in
6515𓀴the night. Her breathing grew stertorous, the mouth opened, and the pale
6516𓀴gums, drawn back, made the teeth look longer and sharper than ever. In a
6517𓀴sort of sleep-waking, vague, unconscious way she opened her eyes, which
6518𓀴were now dull and hard at once, and said in a soft, voluptuous voice,
6519𓀴such as I had never heard from her lips:--
6520𓀴
6521𓀴“Arthur! Oh, my love, I am so glad you have come! Kiss me!” Arthur bent
6522𓀴eagerly over to kiss her; but at that instant Van Helsing, who, like me,
6523𓀴had been startled by her voice, swooped upon him, and catching him by
6524𓀴the neck with both hands, dragged him back with a fury of strength which
6525𓀴I never thought he could have possessed, and actually hurled him almost
6526𓀴across the room.
6527𓀴
6528𓀴“Not for your life!” he said; “not for your living soul and hers!” And
6529𓀴he stood between them like a lion at bay.
6530𓀴
6531𓀴Arthur was so taken aback that he did not for a moment know what to do
6532𓀴or say; and before any impulse of violence could seize him he realised
6533𓀴the place and the occasion, and stood silent, waiting.
6534𓀴
6535𓀴I kept my eyes fixed on Lucy, as did Van Helsing, and we saw a spasm as
6536𓀴of rage flit like a shadow over her face; the sharp teeth champed
6537𓀴together. Then her eyes closed, and she breathed heavily.
6538𓀴
6539𓀴Very shortly after she opened her eyes in all their softness, and
6540𓀴putting out her poor, pale, thin hand, took Van Helsing’s great brown
6541𓀴one; drawing it to her, she kissed it. “My true friend,” she said, in a
6542𓀴faint voice, but with untellable pathos, “My true friend, and his! Oh,
6543𓀴guard him, and give me peace!”
6544𓀴
6545𓀴“I swear it!” he said solemnly, kneeling beside her and holding up his
6546𓀴hand, as one who registers an oath. Then he turned to Arthur, and said
6547𓀴to him: “Come, my child, take her hand in yours, and kiss her on the
6548𓀴forehead, and only once.”
6549𓀴
6550𓀴Their eyes met instead of their lips; and so they parted.
6551𓀴
6552𓀴Lucy’s eyes closed; and Van Helsing, who had been watching closely, took
6553𓀴Arthur’s arm, and drew him away.
6554𓀴
6555𓀴And then Lucy’s breathing became stertorous again, and all at once it
6556𓀴ceased.
6557𓀴
6558𓀴“It is all over,” said Van Helsing. “She is dead!”
6559𓀴
6560𓀴I took Arthur by the arm, and led him away to the drawing-room, where he
6561𓀴sat down, and covered his face with his hands, sobbing in a way that
6562𓀴nearly broke me down to see.
6563𓀴
6564𓀴I went back to the room, and found Van Helsing looking at poor Lucy, and
6565𓀴his face was sterner than ever. Some change had come over her body.
6566𓀴Death had given back part of her beauty, for her brow and cheeks had
6567𓀴recovered some of their flowing lines; even the lips had lost their
6568𓀴deadly pallor. It was as if the blood, no longer needed for the working
6569𓀴of the heart, had gone to make the harshness of death as little rude as
6570𓀴might be.
6571𓀴
6572𓀴    “We thought her dying whilst she slept,
6573𓀴        And sleeping when she died.”
6574𓀴
6575𓀴I stood beside Van Helsing, and said:--
6576𓀴
6577𓀴“Ah, well, poor girl, there is peace for her at last. It is the end!”
6578𓀴
6579𓀴He turned to me, and said with grave solemnity:--
6580𓀴
6581𓀴“Not so; alas! not so. It is only the beginning!”
6582𓀴
6583𓀴When I asked him what he meant, he only shook his head and answered:--
6584𓀴
6585𓀴“We can do nothing as yet. Wait and see.”
6586𓀴
6587𓀴
6588𓀴
6589𓀴
6590𓀴CHAPTER XIII
6591𓀴
6592𓀴DR. SEWARD’S DIARY--_continued_.
6593𓀴
6594𓀴
6595𓀴The funeral was arranged for the next succeeding day, so that Lucy and
6596𓀴her mother might be buried together. I attended to all the ghastly
6597𓀴formalities, and the urbane undertaker proved that his staff were
6598𓀴afflicted--or blessed--with something of his own obsequious suavity.
6599𓀴Even the woman who performed the last offices for the dead remarked to
6600𓀴me, in a confidential, brother-professional way, when she had come out
6601𓀴from the death-chamber:--
6602𓀴
6603𓀴“She makes a very beautiful corpse, sir. It’s quite a privilege to
6604𓀴attend on her. It’s not too much to say that she will do credit to our
6605𓀴establishment!”
6606𓀴
6607𓀴I noticed that Van Helsing never kept far away. This was possible from
6608𓀴the disordered state of things in the household. There were no relatives
6609𓀴at hand; and as Arthur had to be back the next day to attend at his
6610𓀴father’s funeral, we were unable to notify any one who should have been
6611𓀴bidden. Under the circumstances, Van Helsing and I took it upon
6612𓀴ourselves to examine papers, etc. He insisted upon looking over Lucy’s
6613𓀴papers himself. I asked him why, for I feared that he, being a
6614𓀴foreigner, might not be quite aware of English legal requirements, and
6615𓀴so might in ignorance make some unnecessary trouble. He answered me:--
6616𓀴
6617𓀴“I know; I know. You forget that I am a lawyer as well as a doctor. But
6618𓀴this is not altogether for the law. You knew that, when you avoided the
6619𓀴coroner. I have more than him to avoid. There may be papers more--such
6620𓀴as this.”
6621𓀴
6622𓀴As he spoke he took from his pocket-book the memorandum which had been
6623𓀴in Lucy’s breast, and which she had torn in her sleep.
6624𓀴
6625𓀴“When you find anything of the solicitor who is for the late Mrs.
6626𓀴Westenra, seal all her papers, and write him to-night. For me, I watch
6627𓀴here in the room and in Miss Lucy’s old room all night, and I myself
6628𓀴search for what may be. It is not well that her very thoughts go into
6629𓀴the hands of strangers.”
6630𓀴
6631𓀴I went on with my part of the work, and in another half hour had found
6632𓀴the name and address of Mrs. Westenra’s solicitor and had written to
6633𓀴him. All the poor lady’s papers were in order; explicit directions
6634𓀴regarding the place of burial were given. I had hardly sealed the
6635𓀴letter, when, to my surprise, Van Helsing walked into the room,
6636𓀴saying:--
6637𓀴
6638𓀴“Can I help you, friend John? I am free, and if I may, my service is to
6639𓀴you.”
6640𓀴
6641𓀴“Have you got what you looked for?” I asked, to which he replied:--
6642𓀴
6643𓀴“I did not look for any specific thing. I only hoped to find, and find I
6644𓀴have, all that there was--only some letters and a few memoranda, and a
6645𓀴diary new begun. But I have them here, and we shall for the present say
6646𓀴nothing of them. I shall see that poor lad to-morrow evening, and, with
6647𓀴his sanction, I shall use some.”
6648𓀴
6649𓀴When we had finished the work in hand, he said to me:--
6650𓀴
6651𓀴“And now, friend John, I think we may to bed. We want sleep, both you
6652𓀴and I, and rest to recuperate. To-morrow we shall have much to do, but
6653𓀴for the to-night there is no need of us. Alas!”
6654𓀴
6655𓀴Before turning in we went to look at poor Lucy. The undertaker had
6656𓀴certainly done his work well, for the room was turned into a small
6657𓀴_chapelle ardente_. There was a wilderness of beautiful white flowers,
6658𓀴and death was made as little repulsive as might be. The end of the
6659𓀴winding-sheet was laid over the face; when the Professor bent over and
6660𓀴turned it gently back, we both started at the beauty before us, the tall
6661𓀴wax candles showing a sufficient light to note it well. All Lucy’s
6662𓀴loveliness had come back to her in death, and the hours that had passed,
6663𓀴instead of leaving traces of “decay’s effacing fingers,” had but
6664𓀴restored the beauty of life, till positively I could not believe my eyes
6665𓀴that I was looking at a corpse.
6666𓀴
6667𓀴The Professor looked sternly grave. He had not loved her as I had, and
6668𓀴there was no need for tears in his eyes. He said to me: “Remain till I
6669𓀴return,” and left the room. He came back with a handful of wild garlic
6670𓀴from the box waiting in the hall, but which had not been opened, and
6671𓀴placed the flowers amongst the others on and around the bed. Then he
6672𓀴took from his neck, inside his collar, a little gold crucifix, and
6673𓀴placed it over the mouth. He restored the sheet to its place, and we
6674𓀴came away.
6675𓀴
6676𓀴I was undressing in my own room, when, with a premonitory tap at the
6677𓀴door, he entered, and at once began to speak:--
6678𓀴
6679𓀴“To-morrow I want you to bring me, before night, a set of post-mortem
6680𓀴knives.”
6681𓀴
6682𓀴“Must we make an autopsy?” I asked.
6683𓀴
6684𓀴“Yes and no. I want to operate, but not as you think. Let me tell you
6685𓀴now, but not a word to another. I want to cut off her head and take out
6686𓀴her heart. Ah! you a surgeon, and so shocked! You, whom I have seen with
6687𓀴no tremble of hand or heart, do operations of life and death that make
6688𓀴the rest shudder. Oh, but I must not forget, my dear friend John, that
6689𓀴you loved her; and I have not forgotten it, for it is I that shall
6690𓀴operate, and you must only help. I would like to do it to-night, but for
6691𓀴Arthur I must not; he will be free after his father’s funeral to-morrow,
6692𓀴and he will want to see her--to see _it_. Then, when she is coffined
6693𓀴ready for the next day, you and I shall come when all sleep. We shall
6694𓀴unscrew the coffin-lid, and shall do our operation: and then replace
6695𓀴all, so that none know, save we alone.”
6696𓀴
6697𓀴“But why do it at all? The girl is dead. Why mutilate her poor body
6698𓀴without need? And if there is no necessity for a post-mortem and nothing
6699𓀴to gain by it--no good to her, to us, to science, to human
6700𓀴knowledge--why do it? Without such it is monstrous.”
6701𓀴
6702𓀴For answer he put his hand on my shoulder, and said, with infinite
6703𓀴tenderness:--
6704𓀴
6705𓀴“Friend John, I pity your poor bleeding heart; and I love you the more
6706𓀴because it does so bleed. If I could, I would take on myself the burden
6707𓀴that you do bear. But there are things that you know not, but that you
6708𓀴shall know, and bless me for knowing, though they are not pleasant
6709𓀴things. John, my child, you have been my friend now many years, and yet
6710𓀴did you ever know me to do any without good cause? I may err--I am but
6711𓀴man; but I believe in all I do. Was it not for these causes that you
6712𓀴send for me when the great trouble came? Yes! Were you not amazed, nay
6713𓀴horrified, when I would not let Arthur kiss his love--though she was
6714𓀴dying--and snatched him away by all my strength? Yes! And yet you saw
6715𓀴how she thanked me, with her so beautiful dying eyes, her voice, too, so
6716𓀴weak, and she kiss my rough old hand and bless me? Yes! And did you not
6717𓀴hear me swear promise to her, that so she closed her eyes grateful? Yes!
6718𓀴
6719𓀴“Well, I have good reason now for all I want to do. You have for many
6720𓀴years trust me; you have believe me weeks past, when there be things so
6721𓀴strange that you might have well doubt. Believe me yet a little, friend
6722𓀴John. If you trust me not, then I must tell what I think; and that is
6723𓀴not perhaps well. And if I work--as work I shall, no matter trust or no
6724𓀴trust--without my friend trust in me, I work with heavy heart and feel,
6725𓀴oh! so lonely when I want all help and courage that may be!” He paused a
6726𓀴moment and went on solemnly: “Friend John, there are strange and
6727𓀴terrible days before us. Let us not be two, but one, that so we work to
6728𓀴a good end. Will you not have faith in me?”
6729𓀴
6730𓀴I took his hand, and promised him. I held my door open as he went away,
6731𓀴and watched him go into his room and close the door. As I stood without
6732𓀴moving, I saw one of the maids pass silently along the passage--she had
6733𓀴her back towards me, so did not see me--and go into the room where Lucy
6734𓀴lay. The sight touched me. Devotion is so rare, and we are so grateful
6735𓀴to those who show it unasked to those we love. Here was a poor girl
6736𓀴putting aside the terrors which she naturally had of death to go watch
6737𓀴alone by the bier of the mistress whom she loved, so that the poor clay
6738𓀴might not be lonely till laid to eternal rest....
6739𓀴
6740𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
6741𓀴
6742𓀴I must have slept long and soundly, for it was broad daylight when Van
6743𓀴Helsing waked me by coming into my room. He came over to my bedside and
6744𓀴said:--
6745𓀴
6746𓀴“You need not trouble about the knives; we shall not do it.”
6747𓀴
6748𓀴“Why not?” I asked. For his solemnity of the night before had greatly
6749𓀴impressed me.
6750𓀴
6751𓀴“Because,” he said sternly, “it is too late--or too early. See!” Here he
6752𓀴held up the little golden crucifix. “This was stolen in the night.”
6753𓀴
6754𓀴“How, stolen,” I asked in wonder, “since you have it now?”
6755𓀴
6756𓀴“Because I get it back from the worthless wretch who stole it, from the
6757𓀴woman who robbed the dead and the living. Her punishment will surely
