6758𓀴come, but not through me; she knew not altogether what she did and thus
6759𓀴unknowing, she only stole. Now we must wait.”
6760𓀴
6761𓀴He went away on the word, leaving me with a new mystery to think of, a
6762𓀴new puzzle to grapple with.
6763𓀴
6764𓀴The forenoon was a dreary time, but at noon the solicitor came: Mr.
6765𓀴Marquand, of Wholeman, Sons, Marquand & Lidderdale. He was very genial
6766𓀴and very appreciative of what we had done, and took off our hands all
6767𓀴cares as to details. During lunch he told us that Mrs. Westenra had for
6768𓀴some time expected sudden death from her heart, and had put her affairs
6769𓀴in absolute order; he informed us that, with the exception of a certain
6770𓀴entailed property of Lucy’s father’s which now, in default of direct
6771𓀴issue, went back to a distant branch of the family, the whole estate,
6772𓀴real and personal, was left absolutely to Arthur Holmwood. When he had
6773𓀴told us so much he went on:--
6774𓀴
6775𓀴“Frankly we did our best to prevent such a testamentary disposition, and
6776𓀴pointed out certain contingencies that might leave her daughter either
6777𓀴penniless or not so free as she should be to act regarding a matrimonial
6778𓀴alliance. Indeed, we pressed the matter so far that we almost came into
6779𓀴collision, for she asked us if we were or were not prepared to carry out
6780𓀴her wishes. Of course, we had then no alternative but to accept. We were
6781𓀴right in principle, and ninety-nine times out of a hundred we should
6782𓀴have proved, by the logic of events, the accuracy of our judgment.
6783𓀴Frankly, however, I must admit that in this case any other form of
6784𓀴disposition would have rendered impossible the carrying out of her
6785𓀴wishes. For by her predeceasing her daughter the latter would have come
6786𓀴into possession of the property, and, even had she only survived her
6787𓀴mother by five minutes, her property would, in case there were no
6788𓀴will--and a will was a practical impossibility in such a case--have been
6789𓀴treated at her decease as under intestacy. In which case Lord Godalming,
6790𓀴though so dear a friend, would have had no claim in the world; and the
6791𓀴inheritors, being remote, would not be likely to abandon their just
6792𓀴rights, for sentimental reasons regarding an entire stranger. I assure
6793𓀴you, my dear sirs, I am rejoiced at the result, perfectly rejoiced.”
6794𓀴
6795𓀴He was a good fellow, but his rejoicing at the one little part--in which
6796𓀴he was officially interested--of so great a tragedy, was an
6797𓀴object-lesson in the limitations of sympathetic understanding.
6798𓀴
6799𓀴He did not remain long, but said he would look in later in the day and
6800𓀴see Lord Godalming. His coming, however, had been a certain comfort to
6801𓀴us, since it assured us that we should not have to dread hostile
6802𓀴criticism as to any of our acts. Arthur was expected at five o’clock, so
6803𓀴a little before that time we visited the death-chamber. It was so in
6804𓀴very truth, for now both mother and daughter lay in it. The undertaker,
6805𓀴true to his craft, had made the best display he could of his goods, and
6806𓀴there was a mortuary air about the place that lowered our spirits at
6807𓀴once. Van Helsing ordered the former arrangement to be adhered to,
6808𓀴explaining that, as Lord Godalming was coming very soon, it would be
6809𓀴less harrowing to his feelings to see all that was left of his _fiancée_
6810𓀴quite alone. The undertaker seemed shocked at his own stupidity and
6811𓀴exerted himself to restore things to the condition in which we left them
6812𓀴the night before, so that when Arthur came such shocks to his feelings
6813𓀴as we could avoid were saved.
6814𓀴
6815𓀴Poor fellow! He looked desperately sad and broken; even his stalwart
6816𓀴manhood seemed to have shrunk somewhat under the strain of his
6817𓀴much-tried emotions. He had, I knew, been very genuinely and devotedly
6818𓀴attached to his father; and to lose him, and at such a time, was a
6819𓀴bitter blow to him. With me he was warm as ever, and to Van Helsing he
6820𓀴was sweetly courteous; but I could not help seeing that there was some
6821𓀴constraint with him. The Professor noticed it, too, and motioned me to
6822𓀴bring him upstairs. I did so, and left him at the door of the room, as I
6823𓀴felt he would like to be quite alone with her, but he took my arm and
6824𓀴led me in, saying huskily:--
6825𓀴
6826𓀴“You loved her too, old fellow; she told me all about it, and there was
6827𓀴no friend had a closer place in her heart than you. I don’t know how to
6828𓀴thank you for all you have done for her. I can’t think yet....”
6829𓀴
6830𓀴Here he suddenly broke down, and threw his arms round my shoulders and
6831𓀴laid his head on my breast, crying:--
6832𓀴
6833𓀴“Oh, Jack! Jack! What shall I do! The whole of life seems gone from me
6834𓀴all at once, and there is nothing in the wide world for me to live for.”
6835𓀴
6836𓀴I comforted him as well as I could. In such cases men do not need much
6837𓀴expression. A grip of the hand, the tightening of an arm over the
6838𓀴shoulder, a sob in unison, are expressions of sympathy dear to a man’s
6839𓀴heart. I stood still and silent till his sobs died away, and then I said
6840𓀴softly to him:--
6841𓀴
6842𓀴“Come and look at her.”
6843𓀴
6844𓀴Together we moved over to the bed, and I lifted the lawn from her face.
6845𓀴God! how beautiful she was. Every hour seemed to be enhancing her
6846𓀴loveliness. It frightened and amazed me somewhat; and as for Arthur, he
6847𓀴fell a-trembling, and finally was shaken with doubt as with an ague. At
6848𓀴last, after a long pause, he said to me in a faint whisper:--
6849𓀴
6850𓀴“Jack, is she really dead?”
6851𓀴
6852𓀴I assured him sadly that it was so, and went on to suggest--for I felt
6853𓀴that such a horrible doubt should not have life for a moment longer than
6854𓀴I could help--that it often happened that after death faces became
6855𓀴softened and even resolved into their youthful beauty; that this was
6856𓀴especially so when death had been preceded by any acute or prolonged
6857𓀴suffering. It seemed to quite do away with any doubt, and, after
6858𓀴kneeling beside the couch for a while and looking at her lovingly and
6859𓀴long, he turned aside. I told him that that must be good-bye, as the
6860𓀴coffin had to be prepared; so he went back and took her dead hand in his
6861𓀴and kissed it, and bent over and kissed her forehead. He came away,
6862𓀴fondly looking back over his shoulder at her as he came.
6863𓀴
6864𓀴I left him in the drawing-room, and told Van Helsing that he had said
6865𓀴good-bye; so the latter went to the kitchen to tell the undertaker’s men
6866𓀴to proceed with the preparations and to screw up the coffin. When he
6867𓀴came out of the room again I told him of Arthur’s question, and he
6868𓀴replied:--
6869𓀴
6870𓀴“I am not surprised. Just now I doubted for a moment myself!”
6871𓀴
6872𓀴We all dined together, and I could see that poor Art was trying to make
6873𓀴the best of things. Van Helsing had been silent all dinner-time; but
6874𓀴when we had lit our cigars he said--
6875𓀴
6876𓀴“Lord----”; but Arthur interrupted him:--
6877𓀴
6878𓀴“No, no, not that, for God’s sake! not yet at any rate. Forgive me, sir:
6879𓀴I did not mean to speak offensively; it is only because my loss is so
6880𓀴recent.”
6881𓀴
6882𓀴The Professor answered very sweetly:--
6883𓀴
6884𓀴“I only used that name because I was in doubt. I must not call you
6885𓀴‘Mr.,’ and I have grown to love you--yes, my dear boy, to love you--as
6886𓀴Arthur.”
6887𓀴
6888𓀴Arthur held out his hand, and took the old man’s warmly.
6889𓀴
6890𓀴“Call me what you will,” he said. “I hope I may always have the title of
6891𓀴a friend. And let me say that I am at a loss for words to thank you for
6892𓀴your goodness to my poor dear.” He paused a moment, and went on: “I know
6893𓀴that she understood your goodness even better than I do; and if I was
6894𓀴rude or in any way wanting at that time you acted so--you remember”--the
6895𓀴Professor nodded--“you must forgive me.”
6896𓀴
6897𓀴He answered with a grave kindness:--
6898𓀴
6899𓀴“I know it was hard for you to quite trust me then, for to trust such
6900𓀴violence needs to understand; and I take it that you do not--that you
6901𓀴cannot--trust me now, for you do not yet understand. And there may be
6902𓀴more times when I shall want you to trust when you cannot--and may
6903𓀴not--and must not yet understand. But the time will come when your trust
6904𓀴shall be whole and complete in me, and when you shall understand as
6905𓀴though the sunlight himself shone through. Then you shall bless me from
6906𓀴first to last for your own sake, and for the sake of others and for her
6907𓀴dear sake to whom I swore to protect.”
6908𓀴
6909𓀴“And, indeed, indeed, sir,” said Arthur warmly, “I shall in all ways
6910𓀴trust you. I know and believe you have a very noble heart, and you are
6911𓀴Jack’s friend, and you were hers. You shall do what you like.”
6912𓀴
6913𓀴The Professor cleared his throat a couple of times, as though about to
6914𓀴speak, and finally said:--
6915𓀴
6916𓀴“May I ask you something now?”
6917𓀴
6918𓀴“Certainly.”
6919𓀴
6920𓀴“You know that Mrs. Westenra left you all her property?”
6921𓀴
6922𓀴“No, poor dear; I never thought of it.”
6923𓀴
6924𓀴“And as it is all yours, you have a right to deal with it as you will. I
6925𓀴want you to give me permission to read all Miss Lucy’s papers and
6926𓀴letters. Believe me, it is no idle curiosity. I have a motive of which,
6927𓀴be sure, she would have approved. I have them all here. I took them
6928𓀴before we knew that all was yours, so that no strange hand might touch
6929𓀴them--no strange eye look through words into her soul. I shall keep
6930𓀴them, if I may; even you may not see them yet, but I shall keep them
6931𓀴safe. No word shall be lost; and in the good time I shall give them back
6932𓀴to you. It’s a hard thing I ask, but you will do it, will you not, for
6933𓀴Lucy’s sake?”
6934𓀴
6935𓀴Arthur spoke out heartily, like his old self:--
6936𓀴
6937𓀴“Dr. Van Helsing, you may do what you will. I feel that in saying this I
6938𓀴am doing what my dear one would have approved. I shall not trouble you
6939𓀴with questions till the time comes.”
6940𓀴
6941𓀴The old Professor stood up as he said solemnly:--
6942𓀴
6943𓀴“And you are right. There will be pain for us all; but it will not be
6944𓀴all pain, nor will this pain be the last. We and you too--you most of
6945𓀴all, my dear boy--will have to pass through the bitter water before we
6946𓀴reach the sweet. But we must be brave of heart and unselfish, and do our
6947𓀴duty, and all will be well!”
6948𓀴
6949𓀴I slept on a sofa in Arthur’s room that night. Van Helsing did not go to
6950𓀴bed at all. He went to and fro, as if patrolling the house, and was
6951𓀴never out of sight of the room where Lucy lay in her coffin, strewn with
6952𓀴the wild garlic flowers, which sent, through the odour of lily and rose,
6953𓀴a heavy, overpowering smell into the night.
6954𓀴
6955𓀴
6956𓀴_Mina Harker’s Journal._
6957𓀴
6958𓀴_22 September._--In the train to Exeter. Jonathan sleeping.
6959𓀴
6960𓀴It seems only yesterday that the last entry was made, and yet how much
6961𓀴between then, in Whitby and all the world before me, Jonathan away and
6962𓀴no news of him; and now, married to Jonathan, Jonathan a solicitor, a
6963𓀴partner, rich, master of his business, Mr. Hawkins dead and buried, and
6964𓀴Jonathan with another attack that may harm him. Some day he may ask me
6965𓀴about it. Down it all goes. I am rusty in my shorthand--see what
6966𓀴unexpected prosperity does for us--so it may be as well to freshen it up
6967𓀴again with an exercise anyhow....
6968𓀴
6969𓀴The service was very simple and very solemn. There were only ourselves
6970𓀴and the servants there, one or two old friends of his from Exeter, his
6971𓀴London agent, and a gentleman representing Sir John Paxton, the
6972𓀴President of the Incorporated Law Society. Jonathan and I stood hand in
6973𓀴hand, and we felt that our best and dearest friend was gone from us....
6974𓀴
6975𓀴We came back to town quietly, taking a ’bus to Hyde Park Corner.
6976𓀴Jonathan thought it would interest me to go into the Row for a while, so
6977𓀴we sat down; but there were very few people there, and it was
6978𓀴sad-looking and desolate to see so many empty chairs. It made us think
6979𓀴of the empty chair at home; so we got up and walked down Piccadilly.
6980𓀴Jonathan was holding me by the arm, the way he used to in old days
6981𓀴before I went to school. I felt it very improper, for you can’t go on
6982𓀴for some years teaching etiquette and decorum to other girls without the
6983𓀴pedantry of it biting into yourself a bit; but it was Jonathan, and he
6984𓀴was my husband, and we didn’t know anybody who saw us--and we didn’t
6985𓀴care if they did--so on we walked. I was looking at a very beautiful
6986𓀴girl, in a big cart-wheel hat, sitting in a victoria outside Guiliano’s,
6987𓀴when I felt Jonathan clutch my arm so tight that he hurt me, and he said
6988𓀴under his breath: “My God!” I am always anxious about Jonathan, for I
6989𓀴fear that some nervous fit may upset him again; so I turned to him
6990𓀴quickly, and asked him what it was that disturbed him.
6991𓀴
6992𓀴He was very pale, and his eyes seemed bulging out as, half in terror and
6993𓀴half in amazement, he gazed at a tall, thin man, with a beaky nose and
6994𓀴black moustache and pointed beard, who was also observing the pretty
6995𓀴girl. He was looking at her so hard that he did not see either of us,
6996𓀴and so I had a good view of him. His face was not a good face; it was
6997𓀴hard, and cruel, and sensual, and his big white teeth, that looked all
6998𓀴the whiter because his lips were so red, were pointed like an animal’s.
6999𓀴Jonathan kept staring at him, till I was afraid he would notice. I
7000𓀴feared he might take it ill, he looked so fierce and nasty. I asked
7001𓀴Jonathan why he was disturbed, and he answered, evidently thinking that
7002𓀴I knew as much about it as he did: “Do you see who it is?”
7003𓀴
7004𓀴“No, dear,” I said; “I don’t know him; who is it?” His answer seemed to
7005𓀴shock and thrill me, for it was said as if he did not know that it was
7006𓀴to me, Mina, to whom he was speaking:--
7007𓀴
7008𓀴“It is the man himself!”
7009𓀴
7010𓀴The poor dear was evidently terrified at something--very greatly
7011𓀴terrified; I do believe that if he had not had me to lean on and to
7012𓀴support him he would have sunk down. He kept staring; a man came out of
7013𓀴the shop with a small parcel, and gave it to the lady, who then drove
7014𓀴off. The dark man kept his eyes fixed on her, and when the carriage
7015𓀴moved up Piccadilly he followed in the same direction, and hailed a
7016𓀴hansom. Jonathan kept looking after him, and said, as if to himself:--
7017𓀴
7018𓀴“I believe it is the Count, but he has grown young. My God, if this be
7019𓀴so! Oh, my God! my God! If I only knew! if I only knew!” He was
7020𓀴distressing himself so much that I feared to keep his mind on the
7021𓀴subject by asking him any questions, so I remained silent. I drew him
7022𓀴away quietly, and he, holding my arm, came easily. We walked a little
7023𓀴further, and then went in and sat for a while in the Green Park. It was
7024𓀴a hot day for autumn, and there was a comfortable seat in a shady place.
7025𓀴After a few minutes’ staring at nothing, Jonathan’s eyes closed, and he
7026𓀴went quietly into a sleep, with his head on my shoulder. I thought it
7027𓀴was the best thing for him, so did not disturb him. In about twenty
7028𓀴minutes he woke up, and said to me quite cheerfully:--
7029𓀴
7030𓀴“Why, Mina, have I been asleep! Oh, do forgive me for being so rude.
7031𓀴Come, and we’ll have a cup of tea somewhere.” He had evidently forgotten
7032𓀴all about the dark stranger, as in his illness he had forgotten all that
7033𓀴this episode had reminded him of. I don’t like this lapsing into
7034𓀴forgetfulness; it may make or continue some injury to the brain. I must
7035𓀴not ask him, for fear I shall do more harm than good; but I must somehow
7036𓀴learn the facts of his journey abroad. The time is come, I fear, when I
7037𓀴must open that parcel, and know what is written. Oh, Jonathan, you will,
7038𓀴I know, forgive me if I do wrong, but it is for your own dear sake.
7039𓀴
7040𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
7041𓀴
7042𓀴_Later._--A sad home-coming in every way--the house empty of the dear
7043𓀴soul who was so good to us; Jonathan still pale and dizzy under a slight
7044𓀴relapse of his malady; and now a telegram from Van Helsing, whoever he
7045𓀴may be:--
7046𓀴
7047𓀴“You will be grieved to hear that Mrs. Westenra died five days ago, and
7048𓀴that Lucy died the day before yesterday. They were both buried to-day.”
7049𓀴
7050𓀴Oh, what a wealth of sorrow in a few words! Poor Mrs. Westenra! poor
7051𓀴Lucy! Gone, gone, never to return to us! And poor, poor Arthur, to have
7052𓀴lost such sweetness out of his life! God help us all to bear our
7053𓀴troubles.
7054𓀴
7055𓀴
7056𓀴_Dr. Seward’s Diary._
7057𓀴
7058𓀴_22 September._--It is all over. Arthur has gone back to Ring, and has
7059𓀴taken Quincey Morris with him. What a fine fellow is Quincey! I believe
7060𓀴in my heart of hearts that he suffered as much about Lucy’s death as any
7061𓀴of us; but he bore himself through it like a moral Viking. If America
7062𓀴can go on breeding men like that, she will be a power in the world
7063𓀴indeed. Van Helsing is lying down, having a rest preparatory to his
7064𓀴journey. He goes over to Amsterdam to-night, but says he returns
7065𓀴to-morrow night; that he only wants to make some arrangements which can
7066𓀴only be made personally. He is to stop with me then, if he can; he says
7067𓀴he has work to do in London which may take him some time. Poor old
7068𓀴fellow! I fear that the strain of the past week has broken down even his
7069𓀴iron strength. All the time of the burial he was, I could see, putting
7070𓀴some terrible restraint on himself. When it was all over, we were
7071𓀴standing beside Arthur, who, poor fellow, was speaking of his part in
7072𓀴the operation where his blood had been transfused to his Lucy’s veins; I
7073𓀴could see Van Helsing’s face grow white and purple by turns. Arthur was
7074𓀴saying that he felt since then as if they two had been really married
7075𓀴and that she was his wife in the sight of God. None of us said a word of
7076𓀴the other operations, and none of us ever shall. Arthur and Quincey went
7077𓀴away together to the station, and Van Helsing and I came on here. The
7078𓀴moment we were alone in the carriage he gave way to a regular fit of
7079𓀴hysterics. He has denied to me since that it was hysterics, and insisted
7080𓀴that it was only his sense of humour asserting itself under very
7081𓀴terrible conditions. He laughed till he cried, and I had to draw down
7082𓀴the blinds lest any one should see us and misjudge; and then he cried,
7083𓀴till he laughed again; and laughed and cried together, just as a woman
7084𓀴does. I tried to be stern with him, as one is to a woman under the
7085𓀴circumstances; but it had no effect. Men and women are so different in
7086𓀴manifestations of nervous strength or weakness! Then when his face grew
7087𓀴grave and stern again I asked him why his mirth, and why at such a time.
7088𓀴His reply was in a way characteristic of him, for it was logical and
7089𓀴forceful and mysterious. He said:--
7090𓀴
7091𓀴“Ah, you don’t comprehend, friend John. Do not think that I am not sad,
7092𓀴though I laugh. See, I have cried even when the laugh did choke me. But
7093𓀴no more think that I am all sorry when I cry, for the laugh he come
7094𓀴just the same. Keep it always with you that laughter who knock at your
7095𓀴door and say, ‘May I come in?’ is not the true laughter. No! he is a
7096𓀴king, and he come when and how he like. He ask no person; he choose no
7097𓀴time of suitability. He say, ‘I am here.’ Behold, in example I grieve my
7098𓀴heart out for that so sweet young girl; I give my blood for her, though
7099𓀴I am old and worn; I give my time, my skill, my sleep; I let my other
7100𓀴sufferers want that so she may have all. And yet I can laugh at her very
7101𓀴grave--laugh when the clay from the spade of the sexton drop upon her
7102𓀴coffin and say ‘Thud! thud!’ to my heart, till it send back the blood
7103𓀴from my cheek. My heart bleed for that poor boy--that dear boy, so of
7104𓀴the age of mine own boy had I been so blessed that he live, and with his
7105𓀴hair and eyes the same. There, you know now why I love him so. And yet
7106𓀴when he say things that touch my husband-heart to the quick, and make my
7107𓀴father-heart yearn to him as to no other man--not even to you, friend
7108𓀴John, for we are more level in experiences than father and son--yet even
7109𓀴at such moment King Laugh he come to me and shout and bellow in my ear,
7110𓀴‘Here I am! here I am!’ till the blood come dance back and bring some of
7111𓀴the sunshine that he carry with him to my cheek. Oh, friend John, it is
7112𓀴a strange world, a sad world, a world full of miseries, and woes, and
7113𓀴troubles; and yet when King Laugh come he make them all dance to the
7114𓀴tune he play. Bleeding hearts, and dry bones of the churchyard, and
7115𓀴tears that burn as they fall--all dance together to the music that he
7116𓀴make with that smileless mouth of him. And believe me, friend John, that
7117𓀴he is good to come, and kind. Ah, we men and women are like ropes drawn
7118𓀴tight with strain that pull us different ways. Then tears come; and,
7119𓀴like the rain on the ropes, they brace us up, until perhaps the strain
7120𓀴become too great, and we break. But King Laugh he come like the
7121𓀴sunshine, and he ease off the strain again; and we bear to go on with
7122𓀴our labour, what it may be.”
7123𓀴
7124𓀴I did not like to wound him by pretending not to see his idea; but, as I
7125𓀴did not yet understand the cause of his laughter, I asked him. As he
7126𓀴answered me his face grew stern, and he said in quite a different
7127𓀴tone:--
7128𓀴
7129𓀴“Oh, it was the grim irony of it all--this so lovely lady garlanded with
7130𓀴flowers, that looked so fair as life, till one by one we wondered if she
7131𓀴were truly dead; she laid in that so fine marble house in that lonely
7132𓀴churchyard, where rest so many of her kin, laid there with the mother
7133𓀴who loved her, and whom she loved; and that sacred bell going ‘Toll!
7134𓀴toll! toll!’ so sad and slow; and those holy men, with the white
7135𓀴garments of the angel, pretending to read books, and yet all the time
7136𓀴their eyes never on the page; and all of us with the bowed head. And all
7137𓀴for what? She is dead; so! Is it not?”
7138𓀴
7139𓀴“Well, for the life of me, Professor,” I said, “I can’t see anything to
7140𓀴laugh at in all that. Why, your explanation makes it a harder puzzle
7141𓀴than before. But even if the burial service was comic, what about poor
7142𓀴Art and his trouble? Why, his heart was simply breaking.”
7143𓀴
7144𓀴“Just so. Said he not that the transfusion of his blood to her veins had
7145𓀴made her truly his bride?”
7146𓀴
7147𓀴“Yes, and it was a sweet and comforting idea for him.”
7148𓀴
7149𓀴“Quite so. But there was a difficulty, friend John. If so that, then
7150𓀴what about the others? Ho, ho! Then this so sweet maid is a polyandrist,
7151𓀴and me, with my poor wife dead to me, but alive by Church’s law, though
7152𓀴no wits, all gone--even I, who am faithful husband to this now-no-wife,
7153𓀴am bigamist.”
7154𓀴
7155𓀴“I don’t see where the joke comes in there either!” I said; and I did
7156𓀴not feel particularly pleased with him for saying such things. He laid
7157𓀴his hand on my arm, and said:--
7158𓀴
7159𓀴“Friend John, forgive me if I pain. I showed not my feeling to others
7160𓀴when it would wound, but only to you, my old friend, whom I can trust.
7161𓀴If you could have looked into my very heart then when I want to laugh;
7162𓀴if you could have done so when the laugh arrived; if you could do so
7163𓀴now, when King Laugh have pack up his crown, and all that is to him--for
7164𓀴he go far, far away from me, and for a long, long time--maybe you would
7165𓀴perhaps pity me the most of all.”
7166𓀴
7167𓀴I was touched by the tenderness of his tone, and asked why.
7168𓀴
7169𓀴“Because I know!”
7170𓀴
7171𓀴And now we are all scattered; and for many a long day loneliness will
7172𓀴sit over our roofs with brooding wings. Lucy lies in the tomb of her
7173𓀴kin, a lordly death-house in a lonely churchyard, away from teeming
7174𓀴London; where the air is fresh, and the sun rises over Hampstead Hill,
7175𓀴and where wild flowers grow of their own accord.
7176𓀴
7177𓀴So I can finish this diary; and God only knows if I shall ever begin
7178𓀴another. If I do, or if I even open this again, it will be to deal with
7179𓀴different people and different themes; for here at the end, where the
7180𓀴romance of my life is told, ere I go back to take up the thread of my
7181𓀴life-work, I say sadly and without hope,
7182𓀴
7183𓀴                        “FINIS.”
7184𓀴
7185𓀴
7186𓀴_“The Westminster Gazette,” 25 September._
7187𓀴
7188𓀴                          A HAMPSTEAD MYSTERY.
7189𓀴
7190𓀴
7191𓀴The neighbourhood of Hampstead is just at present exercised with a
7192𓀴series of events which seem to run on lines parallel to those of what
7193𓀴was known to the writers of headlines as “The Kensington Horror,” or
7194𓀴“The Stabbing Woman,” or “The Woman in Black.” During the past two or
7195𓀴three days several cases have occurred of young children straying from
7196𓀴home or neglecting to return from their playing on the Heath. In all
7197𓀴these cases the children were too young to give any properly
7198𓀴intelligible account of themselves, but the consensus of their excuses
7199𓀴is that they had been with a “bloofer lady.” It has always been late in
7200𓀴the evening when they have been missed, and on two occasions the
7201𓀴children have not been found until early in the following morning. It is
7202𓀴generally supposed in the neighbourhood that, as the first child missed
7203𓀴gave as his reason for being away that a “bloofer lady” had asked him to
7204𓀴come for a walk, the others had picked up the phrase and used it as
7205𓀴occasion served. This is the more natural as the favourite game of the
7206𓀴little ones at present is luring each other away by wiles. A
7207𓀴correspondent writes us that to see some of the tiny tots pretending to
7208𓀴be the “bloofer lady” is supremely funny. Some of our caricaturists
7209𓀴might, he says, take a lesson in the irony of grotesque by comparing the
7210𓀴reality and the picture. It is only in accordance with general
7211𓀴principles of human nature that the “bloofer lady” should be the popular
7212𓀴rôle at these _al fresco_ performances. Our correspondent naïvely says
7213𓀴that even Ellen Terry could not be so winningly attractive as some of
7214𓀴these grubby-faced little children pretend--and even imagine
7215𓀴themselves--to be.
7216𓀴
7217𓀴There is, however, possibly a serious side to the question, for some of
7218𓀴the children, indeed all who have been missed at night, have been
7219𓀴slightly torn or wounded in the throat. The wounds seem such as might be
7220𓀴made by a rat or a small dog, and although of not much importance
7221𓀴individually, would tend to show that whatever animal inflicts them has
7222𓀴a system or method of its own. The police of the division have been
7223𓀴instructed to keep a sharp look-out for straying children, especially
7224𓀴when very young, in and around Hampstead Heath, and for any stray dog
7225𓀴which may be about.
7226𓀴
7227𓀴
7228𓀴               _“The Westminster Gazette,” 25 September._
7229𓀴
7230𓀴                            _Extra Special._
7231𓀴
7232𓀴                         THE HAMPSTEAD HORROR.
7233𓀴
7234𓀴                         ANOTHER CHILD INJURED.
7235𓀴
7236𓀴                         _The “Bloofer Lady.”_
7237𓀴
7238𓀴We have just received intelligence that another child, missed last
7239𓀴night, was only discovered late in the morning under a furze bush at the
7240𓀴Shooter’s Hill side of Hampstead Heath, which is, perhaps, less
7241𓀴frequented than the other parts. It has the same tiny wound in the
7242𓀴throat as has been noticed in other cases. It was terribly weak, and
7243𓀴looked quite emaciated. It too, when partially restored, had the common
7244𓀴story to tell of being lured away by the “bloofer lady.”
7245𓀴
7246𓀴
7247𓀴
7248𓀴
7249𓀴CHAPTER XIV
7250𓀴
7251𓀴MINA HARKER’S JOURNAL
7252𓀴
7253𓀴
7254𓀴_23 September_.--Jonathan is better after a bad night. I am so glad that
7255𓀴he has plenty of work to do, for that keeps his mind off the terrible
7256𓀴things; and oh, I am rejoiced that he is not now weighed down with the
7257𓀴responsibility of his new position. I knew he would be true to himself,
7258𓀴and now how proud I am to see my Jonathan rising to the height of his
7259𓀴advancement and keeping pace in all ways with the duties that come upon
7260𓀴him. He will be away all day till late, for he said he could not lunch
7261𓀴at home. My household work is done, so I shall take his foreign journal,
7262𓀴and lock myself up in my room and read it....
7263𓀴
7264𓀴
7265𓀴_24 September_.--I hadn’t the heart to write last night; that terrible
7266𓀴record of Jonathan’s upset me so. Poor dear! How he must have suffered,
7267𓀴whether it be true or only imagination. I wonder if there is any truth
7268𓀴in it at all. Did he get his brain fever, and then write all those
7269𓀴terrible things, or had he some cause for it all? I suppose I shall
7270𓀴never know, for I dare not open the subject to him.... And yet that man
7271𓀴we saw yesterday! He seemed quite certain of him.... Poor fellow! I
7272𓀴suppose it was the funeral upset him and sent his mind back on some
7273𓀴train of thought.... He believes it all himself. I remember how on our
7274𓀴wedding-day he said: “Unless some solemn duty come upon me to go back to
7275𓀴the bitter hours, asleep or awake, mad or sane.” There seems to be
7276𓀴through it all some thread of continuity.... That fearful Count was
7277𓀴coming to London.... If it should be, and he came to London, with his
7278𓀴teeming millions.... There may be a solemn duty; and if it come we must
7279𓀴not shrink from it.... I shall be prepared. I shall get my typewriter
7280𓀴this very hour and begin transcribing. Then we shall be ready for other
7281𓀴eyes if required. And if it be wanted; then, perhaps, if I am ready,
7282𓀴poor Jonathan may not be upset, for I can speak for him and never let
7283𓀴him be troubled or worried with it at all. If ever Jonathan quite gets
7284𓀴over the nervousness he may want to tell me of it all, and I can ask him
7285𓀴questions and find out things, and see how I may comfort him.
7286𓀴
7287𓀴
7288𓀴_Letter, Van Helsing to Mrs. Harker._
7289𓀴
7290𓀴“_24 September._
7291𓀴
7292𓀴(_Confidence_)
7293𓀴
7294𓀴“Dear Madam,--
7295𓀴
7296𓀴“I pray you to pardon my writing, in that I am so far friend as that I
7297𓀴sent to you sad news of Miss Lucy Westenra’s death. By the kindness of
7298𓀴Lord Godalming, I am empowered to read her letters and papers, for I am
7299𓀴deeply concerned about certain matters vitally important. In them I find
7300𓀴some letters from you, which show how great friends you were and how you
7301𓀴love her. Oh, Madam Mina, by that love, I implore you, help me. It is
7302𓀴for others’ good that I ask--to redress great wrong, and to lift much
7303𓀴and terrible troubles--that may be more great than you can know. May it
7304𓀴be that I see you? You can trust me. I am friend of Dr. John Seward and
7305𓀴of Lord Godalming (that was Arthur of Miss Lucy). I must keep it private
7306𓀴for the present from all. I should come to Exeter to see you at once if
7307𓀴you tell me I am privilege to come, and where and when. I implore your
7308𓀴pardon, madam. I have read your letters to poor Lucy, and know how good
7309𓀴you are and how your husband suffer; so I pray you, if it may be,
7310𓀴enlighten him not, lest it may harm. Again your pardon, and forgive me.
7311𓀴
7312𓀴“VAN HELSING.”
7313𓀴
7314𓀴
7315𓀴_Telegram, Mrs. Harker to Van Helsing._
7316𓀴
7317𓀴“_25 September._--Come to-day by quarter-past ten train if you can catch
7318𓀴it. Can see you any time you call.
7319𓀴
7320𓀴“WILHELMINA HARKER.”
7321𓀴
7322𓀴MINA HARKER’S JOURNAL.
7323𓀴
7324𓀴_25 September._--I cannot help feeling terribly excited as the time
7325𓀴draws near for the visit of Dr. Van Helsing, for somehow I expect that
7326𓀴it will throw some light upon Jonathan’s sad experience; and as he
7327𓀴attended poor dear Lucy in her last illness, he can tell me all about
7328𓀴her. That is the reason of his coming; it is concerning Lucy and her
7329𓀴sleep-walking, and not about Jonathan. Then I shall never know the real
7330𓀴truth now! How silly I am. That awful journal gets hold of my
7331𓀴imagination and tinges everything with something of its own colour. Of
7332𓀴course it is about Lucy. That habit came back to the poor dear, and that
7333𓀴awful night on the cliff must have made her ill. I had almost forgotten
7334𓀴in my own affairs how ill she was afterwards. She must have told him
7335𓀴of her sleep-walking adventure on the cliff, and that I knew all about
7336𓀴it; and now he wants me to tell him what she knows, so that he may
7337𓀴understand. I hope I did right in not saying anything of it to Mrs.
7338𓀴Westenra; I should never forgive myself if any act of mine, were it even
7339𓀴a negative one, brought harm on poor dear Lucy. I hope, too, Dr. Van
7340𓀴Helsing will not blame me; I have had so much trouble and anxiety of
7341𓀴late that I feel I cannot bear more just at present.
7342𓀴
7343𓀴I suppose a cry does us all good at times--clears the air as other rain
7344𓀴does. Perhaps it was reading the journal yesterday that upset me, and
7345𓀴then Jonathan went away this morning to stay away from me a whole day
7346𓀴and night, the first time we have been parted since our marriage. I do
7347𓀴hope the dear fellow will take care of himself, and that nothing will
7348𓀴occur to upset him. It is two o’clock, and the doctor will be here soon
7349𓀴now. I shall say nothing of Jonathan’s journal unless he asks me. I am
7350𓀴so glad I have type-written out my own journal, so that, in case he asks
7351𓀴about Lucy, I can hand it to him; it will save much questioning.
7352𓀴
7353𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
7354𓀴
7355𓀴_Later._--He has come and gone. Oh, what a strange meeting, and how it
7356𓀴all makes my head whirl round! I feel like one in a dream. Can it be all
7357𓀴possible, or even a part of it? If I had not read Jonathan’s journal
7358𓀴first, I should never have accepted even a possibility. Poor, poor, dear
7359𓀴Jonathan! How he must have suffered. Please the good God, all this may
7360𓀴not upset him again. I shall try to save him from it; but it may be even
7361𓀴a consolation and a help to him--terrible though it be and awful in its
7362𓀴consequences--to know for certain that his eyes and ears and brain did
7363𓀴not deceive him, and that it is all true. It may be that it is the doubt
7364𓀴which haunts him; that when the doubt is removed, no matter
7365𓀴which--waking or dreaming--may prove the truth, he will be more
7366𓀴satisfied and better able to bear the shock. Dr. Van Helsing must be a
7367𓀴good man as well as a clever one if he is Arthur’s friend and Dr.
7368𓀴Seward’s, and if they brought him all the way from Holland to look after
7369𓀴Lucy. I feel from having seen him that he _is_ good and kind and of a
7370𓀴noble nature. When he comes to-morrow I shall ask him about Jonathan;
7371𓀴and then, please God, all this sorrow and anxiety may lead to a good
7372𓀴end. I used to think I would like to practise interviewing; Jonathan’s
7373𓀴friend on “The Exeter News” told him that memory was everything in such
7374𓀴work--that you must be able to put down exactly almost every word
7375𓀴spoken, even if you had to refine some of it afterwards. Here was a rare
7376𓀴interview; I shall try to record it _verbatim_.
7377𓀴
7378𓀴It was half-past two o’clock when the knock came. I took my courage _à
7379𓀴deux mains_ and waited. In a few minutes Mary opened the door, and
7380𓀴announced “Dr. Van Helsing.”
7381𓀴
7382𓀴I rose and bowed, and he came towards me; a man of medium weight,
7383𓀴strongly built, with his shoulders set back over a broad, deep chest and
7384𓀴a neck well balanced on the trunk as the head is on the neck. The poise
7385𓀴of the head strikes one at once as indicative of thought and power; the
7386𓀴head is noble, well-sized, broad, and large behind the ears. The face,
7387𓀴clean-shaven, shows a hard, square chin, a large, resolute, mobile
7388𓀴mouth, a good-sized nose, rather straight, but with quick, sensitive
7389𓀴nostrils, that seem to broaden as the big, bushy brows come down and the
7390𓀴mouth tightens. The forehead is broad and fine, rising at first almost
7391𓀴straight and then sloping back above two bumps or ridges wide apart;
7392𓀴such a forehead that the reddish hair cannot possibly tumble over it,
7393𓀴but falls naturally back and to the sides. Big, dark blue eyes are set
7394𓀴widely apart, and are quick and tender or stern with the man’s moods. He
7395𓀴said to me:--
7396𓀴
7397𓀴“Mrs. Harker, is it not?” I bowed assent.
7398𓀴
7399𓀴“That was Miss Mina Murray?” Again I assented.
7400𓀴
7401𓀴“It is Mina Murray that I came to see that was friend of that poor dear
7402𓀴child Lucy Westenra. Madam Mina, it is on account of the dead I come.”
7403𓀴
7404𓀴“Sir,” I said, “you could have no better claim on me than that you were
7405𓀴a friend and helper of Lucy Westenra.” And I held out my hand. He took
7406𓀴it and said tenderly:--
7407𓀴
7408𓀴“Oh, Madam Mina, I knew that the friend of that poor lily girl must be
7409𓀴good, but I had yet to learn----” He finished his speech with a courtly
7410𓀴bow. I asked him what it was that he wanted to see me about, so he at
7411𓀴once began:--
7412𓀴
7413𓀴“I have read your letters to Miss Lucy. Forgive me, but I had to begin
7414𓀴to inquire somewhere, and there was none to ask. I know that you were
7415𓀴with her at Whitby. She sometimes kept a diary--you need not look
7416𓀴surprised, Madam Mina; it was begun after you had left, and was in
7417𓀴imitation of you--and in that diary she traces by inference certain
7418𓀴things to a sleep-walking in which she puts down that you saved her. In
7419𓀴great perplexity then I come to you, and ask you out of your so much
7420𓀴kindness to tell me all of it that you can remember.”
7421𓀴
7422𓀴“I can tell you, I think, Dr. Van Helsing, all about it.”
7423𓀴
7424𓀴“Ah, then you have good memory for facts, for details? It is not always
7425𓀴so with young ladies.”
7426𓀴
7427𓀴“No, doctor, but I wrote it all down at the time. I can show it to you
7428𓀴if you like.”
7429𓀴
7430𓀴“Oh, Madam Mina, I will be grateful; you will do me much favour.” I
7431𓀴could not resist the temptation of mystifying him a bit--I suppose it is
7432𓀴some of the taste of the original apple that remains still in our
7433𓀴mouths--so I handed him the shorthand diary. He took it with a grateful
7434𓀴bow, and said:--
7435𓀴
7436𓀴“May I read it?”
7437𓀴
7438𓀴“If you wish,” I answered as demurely as I could. He opened it, and for
7439𓀴an instant his face fell. Then he stood up and bowed.
7440𓀴
7441𓀴“Oh, you so clever woman!” he said. “I knew long that Mr. Jonathan was a
7442𓀴man of much thankfulness; but see, his wife have all the good things.
7443𓀴And will you not so much honour me and so help me as to read it for me?
7444𓀴Alas! I know not the shorthand.” By this time my little joke was over,
7445𓀴and I was almost ashamed; so I took the typewritten copy from my
7446𓀴workbasket and handed it to him.
7447𓀴
7448𓀴“Forgive me,” I said: “I could not help it; but I had been thinking that
7449𓀴it was of dear Lucy that you wished to ask, and so that you might not
7450𓀴have time to wait--not on my account, but because I know your time must
7451𓀴be precious--I have written it out on the typewriter for you.”
7452𓀴
7453𓀴He took it and his eyes glistened. “You are so good,” he said. “And may
7454𓀴I read it now? I may want to ask you some things when I have read.”
7455𓀴
7456𓀴“By all means,” I said, “read it over whilst I order lunch; and then you
7457𓀴can ask me questions whilst we eat.” He bowed and settled himself in a
7458𓀴chair with his back to the light, and became absorbed in the papers,
7459𓀴whilst I went to see after lunch chiefly in order that he might not be
7460𓀴disturbed. When I came back, I found him walking hurriedly up and down
7461𓀴the room, his face all ablaze with excitement. He rushed up to me and
7462𓀴took me by both hands.
7463𓀴
7464𓀴“Oh, Madam Mina,” he said, “how can I say what I owe to you? This paper
7465𓀴is as sunshine. It opens the gate to me. I am daze, I am dazzle, with so
7466𓀴much light, and yet clouds roll in behind the light every time. But that
7467𓀴you do not, cannot, comprehend. Oh, but I am grateful to you, you so
7468𓀴clever woman. Madam”--he said this very solemnly--“if ever Abraham Van
7469𓀴Helsing can do anything for you or yours, I trust you will let me know.
7470𓀴It will be pleasure and delight if I may serve you as a friend; as a
7471𓀴friend, but all I have ever learned, all I can ever do, shall be for you
7472𓀴and those you love. There are darknesses in life, and there are lights;
7473𓀴you are one of the lights. You will have happy life and good life, and
7474𓀴your husband will be blessed in you.”
7475𓀴
7476𓀴“But, doctor, you praise me too much, and--and you do not know me.”
7477𓀴
7478𓀴“Not know you--I, who am old, and who have studied all my life men and
7479𓀴women; I, who have made my specialty the brain and all that belongs to
7480𓀴him and all that follow from him! And I have read your diary that you
7481𓀴have so goodly written for me, and which breathes out truth in every
7482𓀴line. I, who have read your so sweet letter to poor Lucy of your
7483𓀴marriage and your trust, not know you! Oh, Madam Mina, good women tell
7484𓀴all their lives, and by day and by hour and by minute, such things that
7485𓀴angels can read; and we men who wish to know have in us something of
7486𓀴angels’ eyes. Your husband is noble nature, and you are noble too, for
7487𓀴you trust, and trust cannot be where there is mean nature. And your
7488𓀴husband--tell me of him. Is he quite well? Is all that fever gone, and
7489𓀴is he strong and hearty?” I saw here an opening to ask him about
7490𓀴Jonathan, so I said:--
7491𓀴
7492𓀴“He was almost recovered, but he has been greatly upset by Mr. Hawkins’s
7493𓀴death.” He interrupted:--
7494𓀴
7495𓀴“Oh, yes, I know, I know. I have read your last two letters.” I went
7496𓀴on:--
7497𓀴
7498𓀴“I suppose this upset him, for when we were in town on Thursday last he
7499𓀴had a sort of shock.”
7500𓀴
7501𓀴“A shock, and after brain fever so soon! That was not good. What kind of
7502𓀴a shock was it?”
7503𓀴
7504𓀴“He thought he saw some one who recalled something terrible, something
7505𓀴which led to his brain fever.” And here the whole thing seemed to
7506𓀴overwhelm me in a rush. The pity for Jonathan, the horror which he
7507𓀴experienced, the whole fearful mystery of his diary, and the fear that
7508𓀴has been brooding over me ever since, all came in a tumult. I suppose I
7509𓀴was hysterical, for I threw myself on my knees and held up my hands to
7510𓀴him, and implored him to make my husband well again. He took my hands
7511𓀴and raised me up, and made me sit on the sofa, and sat by me; he held my
7512𓀴hand in his, and said to me with, oh, such infinite sweetness:--
7513𓀴
7514𓀴“My life is a barren and lonely one, and so full of work that I have not
7515𓀴had much time for friendships; but since I have been summoned to here by
7516𓀴my friend John Seward I have known so many good people and seen such
7517𓀴nobility that I feel more than ever--and it has grown with my advancing
7518𓀴years--the loneliness of my life. Believe, me, then, that I come here
7519𓀴full of respect for you, and you have given me hope--hope, not in what I
7520𓀴am seeking of, but that there are good women still left to make life
7521𓀴happy--good women, whose lives and whose truths may make good lesson for
7522𓀴the children that are to be. I am glad, glad, that I may here be of some
7523𓀴use to you; for if your husband suffer, he suffer within the range of my
7524𓀴study and experience. I promise you that I will gladly do _all_ for him
7525𓀴that I can--all to make his life strong and manly, and your life a happy
7526𓀴one. Now you must eat. You are overwrought and perhaps over-anxious.
7527𓀴Husband Jonathan would not like to see you so pale; and what he like not
7528𓀴where he love, is not to his good. Therefore for his sake you must eat
7529𓀴and smile. You have told me all about Lucy, and so now we shall not
7530𓀴speak of it, lest it distress. I shall stay in Exeter to-night, for I
7531𓀴want to think much over what you have told me, and when I have thought I
7532𓀴will ask you questions, if I may. And then, too, you will tell me of
7533𓀴husband Jonathan’s trouble so far as you can, but not yet. You must eat
7534𓀴now; afterwards you shall tell me all.”
7535𓀴
7536𓀴After lunch, when we went back to the drawing-room, he said to me:--
7537𓀴
7538𓀴“And now tell me all about him.” When it came to speaking to this great
7539𓀴learned man, I began to fear that he would think me a weak fool, and
7540𓀴Jonathan a madman--that journal is all so strange--and I hesitated to go
7541𓀴on. But he was so sweet and kind, and he had promised to help, and I
7542𓀴trusted him, so I said:--
7543𓀴
7544𓀴“Dr. Van Helsing, what I have to tell you is so queer that you must not
7545𓀴laugh at me or at my husband. I have been since yesterday in a sort of
7546𓀴fever of doubt; you must be kind to me, and not think me foolish that I
7547𓀴have even half believed some very strange things.” He reassured me by
7548𓀴his manner as well as his words when he said:--
7549𓀴
7550𓀴“Oh, my dear, if you only know how strange is the matter regarding which
7551𓀴I am here, it is you who would laugh. I have learned not to think little
7552𓀴of any one’s belief, no matter how strange it be. I have tried to keep
7553𓀴an open mind; and it is not the ordinary things of life that could close
7554𓀴it, but the strange things, the extraordinary things, the things that
7555𓀴make one doubt if they be mad or sane.”
7556𓀴
7557𓀴“Thank you, thank you, a thousand times! You have taken a weight off my
7558𓀴mind. If you will let me, I shall give you a paper to read. It is long,
7559𓀴but I have typewritten it out. It will tell you my trouble and
7560𓀴Jonathan’s. It is the copy of his journal when abroad, and all that
7561𓀴happened. I dare not say anything of it; you will read for yourself and
7562𓀴judge. And then when I see you, perhaps, you will be very kind and tell
7563𓀴me what you think.”
7564𓀴
7565𓀴“I promise,” he said as I gave him the papers; “I shall in the morning,
7566𓀴so soon as I can, come to see you and your husband, if I may.”
7567𓀴
7568𓀴“Jonathan will be here at half-past eleven, and you must come to lunch
7569𓀴with us and see him then; you could catch the quick 3:34 train, which
7570𓀴will leave you at Paddington before eight.” He was surprised at my
7571𓀴knowledge of the trains off-hand, but he does not know that I have made
7572𓀴up all the trains to and from Exeter, so that I may help Jonathan in
7573𓀴case he is in a hurry.
7574𓀴
7575𓀴So he took the papers with him and went away, and I sit here
7576𓀴thinking--thinking I don’t know what.
7577𓀴
7578𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
7579𓀴
7580𓀴_Letter (by hand), Van Helsing to Mrs. Harker._
7581𓀴
7582𓀴“_25 September, 6 o’clock._
7583𓀴
7584𓀴“Dear Madam Mina,--
7585𓀴
7586𓀴“I have read your husband’s so wonderful diary. You may sleep without
7587𓀴doubt. Strange and terrible as it is, it is _true_! I will pledge my
7588𓀴life on it. It may be worse for others; but for him and you there is no
7589𓀴dread. He is a noble fellow; and let me tell you from experience of men,
7590𓀴that one who would do as he did in going down that wall and to that
7591𓀴room--ay, and going a second time--is not one to be injured in
7592𓀴permanence by a shock. His brain and his heart are all right; this I
7593𓀴swear, before I have even seen him; so be at rest. I shall have much to
7594𓀴ask him of other things. I am blessed that to-day I come to see you, for
7595𓀴I have learn all at once so much that again I am dazzle--dazzle more
7596𓀴than ever, and I must think.
7597𓀴
7598𓀴“Yours the most faithful,
7599𓀴
7600𓀴“ABRAHAM VAN HELSING.”
7601𓀴
7602𓀴
7603𓀴_Letter, Mrs. Harker to Van Helsing._
7604𓀴
7605𓀴“_25 September, 6:30 p. m._
7606𓀴
7607𓀴“My dear Dr. Van Helsing,--
7608𓀴
7609𓀴“A thousand thanks for your kind letter, which has taken a great weight
7610𓀴off my mind. And yet, if it be true, what terrible things there are in
7611𓀴the world, and what an awful thing if that man, that monster, be really
7612𓀴in London! I fear to think. I have this moment, whilst writing, had a
7613𓀴wire from Jonathan, saying that he leaves by the 6:25 to-night from
7614𓀴Launceston and will be here at 10:18, so that I shall have no fear
7615𓀴to-night. Will you, therefore, instead of lunching with us, please come
7616𓀴to breakfast at eight o’clock, if this be not too early for you? You can
7617𓀴get away, if you are in a hurry, by the 10:30 train, which will bring
7618𓀴you to Paddington by 2:35. Do not answer this, as I shall take it that,
7619𓀴if I do not hear, you will come to breakfast.
7620𓀴
7621𓀴“Believe me,
7622𓀴
7623𓀴“Your faithful and grateful friend,
7624𓀴
7625𓀴“MINA HARKER.”
7626𓀴
7627𓀴
7628𓀴_Jonathan Harker’s Journal._
7629𓀴
7630𓀴_26 September._--I thought never to write in this diary again, but the
7631𓀴time has come. When I got home last night Mina had supper ready, and
7632𓀴when we had supped she told me of Van Helsing’s visit, and of her having
7633𓀴given him the two diaries copied out, and of how anxious she has been
7634𓀴about me. She showed me in the doctor’s letter that all I wrote down was
7635𓀴true. It seems to have made a new man of me. It was the doubt as to the
7636𓀴reality of the whole thing that knocked me over. I felt impotent, and in
7637𓀴the dark, and distrustful. But, now that I _know_, I am not afraid, even
7638𓀴of the Count. He has succeeded after all, then, in his design in getting
7639𓀴to London, and it was he I saw. He has got younger, and how? Van Helsing
7640𓀴is the man to unmask him and hunt him out, if he is anything like what
7641𓀴Mina says. We sat late, and talked it all over. Mina is dressing, and I
7642𓀴shall call at the hotel in a few minutes and bring him over....
7643𓀴
7644𓀴He was, I think, surprised to see me. When I came into the room where he
7645𓀴was, and introduced myself, he took me by the shoulder, and turned my
7646𓀴face round to the light, and said, after a sharp scrutiny:--
7647𓀴
7648𓀴“But Madam Mina told me you were ill, that you had had a shock.” It was
7649𓀴so funny to hear my wife called “Madam Mina” by this kindly,
7650𓀴strong-faced old man. I smiled, and said:--
7651𓀴
7652𓀴“I _was_ ill, I _have_ had a shock; but you have cured me already.”
7653𓀴
7654𓀴“And how?”
7655𓀴
7656𓀴“By your letter to Mina last night. I was in doubt, and then everything
7657𓀴took a hue of unreality, and I did not know what to trust, even the
7658𓀴evidence of my own senses. Not knowing what to trust, I did not know
7659𓀴what to do; and so had only to keep on working in what had hitherto been
7660𓀴the groove of my life. The groove ceased to avail me, and I mistrusted
7661𓀴myself. Doctor, you don’t know what it is to doubt everything, even
7662𓀴yourself. No, you don’t; you couldn’t with eyebrows like yours.” He
7663𓀴seemed pleased, and laughed as he said:--
7664𓀴
7665𓀴“So! You are physiognomist. I learn more here with each hour. I am with
7666𓀴so much pleasure coming to you to breakfast; and, oh, sir, you will
7667𓀴pardon praise from an old man, but you are blessed in your wife.” I
7668𓀴would listen to him go on praising Mina for a day, so I simply nodded
7669𓀴and stood silent.
7670𓀴
7671𓀴“She is one of God’s women, fashioned by His own hand to show us men and
7672𓀴other women that there is a heaven where we can enter, and that its
7673𓀴light can be here on earth. So true, so sweet, so noble, so little an
7674𓀴egoist--and that, let me tell you, is much in this age, so sceptical and
7675𓀴selfish. And you, sir--I have read all the letters to poor Miss Lucy,
7676𓀴and some of them speak of you, so I know you since some days from the
7677𓀴knowing of others; but I have seen your true self since last night. You
7678𓀴will give me your hand, will you not? And let us be friends for all our
7679𓀴lives.”
7680𓀴
7681𓀴We shook hands, and he was so earnest and so kind that it made me quite
7682𓀴choky.
7683𓀴
7684𓀴“And now,” he said, “may I ask you for some more help? I have a great
7685𓀴task to do, and at the beginning it is to know. You can help me here.
7686𓀴Can you tell me what went before your going to Transylvania? Later on I
7687𓀴may ask more help, and of a different kind; but at first this will do.”
7688𓀴
7689𓀴“Look here, sir,” I said, “does what you have to do concern the Count?”
7690𓀴
7691𓀴“It does,” he said solemnly.
7692𓀴
7693𓀴“Then I am with you heart and soul. As you go by the 10:30 train, you
7694𓀴will not have time to read them; but I shall get the bundle of papers.
7695𓀴You can take them with you and read them in the train.”
7696𓀴
7697𓀴After breakfast I saw him to the station. When we were parting he
7698𓀴said:--
7699𓀴
7700𓀴“Perhaps you will come to town if I send to you, and take Madam Mina
7701𓀴too.”
7702𓀴
7703𓀴“We shall both come when you will,” I said.
7704𓀴
7705𓀴I had got him the morning papers and the London papers of the previous
7706𓀴night, and while we were talking at the carriage window, waiting for the
7707𓀴train to start, he was turning them over. His eyes suddenly seemed to
7708𓀴catch something in one of them, “The Westminster Gazette”--I knew it by
7709𓀴the colour--and he grew quite white. He read something intently,
7710𓀴groaning to himself: “Mein Gott! Mein Gott! So soon! so soon!” I do not
7711𓀴think he remembered me at the moment. Just then the whistle blew, and
7712𓀴the train moved off. This recalled him to himself, and he leaned out of
7713𓀴the window and waved his hand, calling out: “Love to Madam Mina; I shall
7714𓀴write so soon as ever I can.”
7715𓀴
7716𓀴
7717𓀴_Dr. Seward’s Diary._
7718𓀴
7719𓀴_26 September._--Truly there is no such thing as finality. Not a week
7720𓀴since I said “Finis,” and yet here I am starting fresh again, or rather
7721𓀴going on with the same record. Until this afternoon I had no cause to
7722𓀴think of what is done. Renfield had become, to all intents, as sane as
7723𓀴he ever was. He was already well ahead with his fly business; and he had
7724𓀴just started in the spider line also; so he had not been of any trouble
7725𓀴to me. I had a letter from Arthur, written on Sunday, and from it I
7726𓀴gather that he is bearing up wonderfully well. Quincey Morris is with
7727𓀴him, and that is much of a help, for he himself is a bubbling well of
7728𓀴good spirits. Quincey wrote me a line too, and from him I hear that
7729𓀴Arthur is beginning to recover something of his old buoyancy; so as to
7730𓀴them all my mind is at rest. As for myself, I was settling down to my
7731𓀴work with the enthusiasm which I used to have for it, so that I might
7732𓀴fairly have said that the wound which poor Lucy left on me was becoming
7733𓀴cicatrised. Everything is, however, now reopened; and what is to be the
7734𓀴end God only knows. I have an idea that Van Helsing thinks he knows,
7735𓀴too, but he will only let out enough at a time to whet curiosity. He
7736𓀴went to Exeter yesterday, and stayed there all night. To-day he came
7737𓀴back, and almost bounded into the room at about half-past five o’clock,
7738𓀴and thrust last night’s “Westminster Gazette” into my hand.
7739𓀴
7740𓀴“What do you think of that?” he asked as he stood back and folded his
7741𓀴arms.
7742𓀴
7743𓀴I looked over the paper, for I really did not know what he meant; but he
7744𓀴took it from me and pointed out a paragraph about children being decoyed
7745𓀴away at Hampstead. It did not convey much to me, until I reached a
7746𓀴passage where it described small punctured wounds on their throats. An
7747𓀴idea struck me, and I looked up. “Well?” he said.
7748𓀴
7749𓀴“It is like poor Lucy’s.”
7750𓀴
7751𓀴“And what do you make of it?”
7752𓀴
7753𓀴“Simply that there is some cause in common. Whatever it was that injured
7754𓀴her has injured them.” I did not quite understand his answer:--
7755𓀴
7756𓀴“That is true indirectly, but not directly.”
7757𓀴
7758𓀴“How do you mean, Professor?” I asked. I was a little inclined to take
7759𓀴his seriousness lightly--for, after all, four days of rest and freedom
7760𓀴from burning, harrowing anxiety does help to restore one’s spirits--but
7761𓀴when I saw his face, it sobered me. Never, even in the midst of our
7762𓀴despair about poor Lucy, had he looked more stern.
7763𓀴
7764𓀴“Tell me!” I said. “I can hazard no opinion. I do not know what to
7765𓀴think, and I have no data on which to found a conjecture.”
7766𓀴
7767𓀴“Do you mean to tell me, friend John, that you have no suspicion as to
7768𓀴what poor Lucy died of; not after all the hints given, not only by
7769𓀴events, but by me?”
7770𓀴
7771𓀴“Of nervous prostration following on great loss or waste of blood.”
7772𓀴
7773𓀴“And how the blood lost or waste?” I shook my head. He stepped over and
7774𓀴sat down beside me, and went on:--
7775𓀴
7776𓀴“You are clever man, friend John; you reason well, and your wit is bold;
7777𓀴but you are too prejudiced. You do not let your eyes see nor your ears
7778𓀴hear, and that which is outside your daily life is not of account to
7779𓀴you. Do you not think that there are things which you cannot understand,
7780𓀴and yet which are; that some people see things that others cannot? But
7781𓀴there are things old and new which must not be contemplate by men’s
7782𓀴eyes, because they know--or think they know--some things which other men
7783𓀴have told them. Ah, it is the fault of our science that it wants to
7784𓀴explain all; and if it explain not, then it says there is nothing to
7785𓀴explain. But yet we see around us every day the growth of new beliefs,
7786𓀴which think themselves new; and which are yet but the old, which pretend
7787𓀴to be young--like the fine ladies at the opera. I suppose now you do not
7788𓀴believe in corporeal transference. No? Nor in materialisation. No? Nor
7789𓀴in astral bodies. No? Nor in the reading of thought. No? Nor in
7790𓀴hypnotism----”
7791𓀴
7792𓀴“Yes,” I said. “Charcot has proved that pretty well.” He smiled as he
7793𓀴went on: “Then you are satisfied as to it. Yes? And of course then you
7794𓀴understand how it act, and can follow the mind of the great
7795𓀴Charcot--alas that he is no more!--into the very soul of the patient
7796𓀴that he influence. No? Then, friend John, am I to take it that you
7797𓀴simply accept fact, and are satisfied to let from premise to conclusion
7798𓀴be a blank? No? Then tell me--for I am student of the brain--how you
7799𓀴accept the hypnotism and reject the thought reading. Let me tell you, my
7800𓀴friend, that there are things done to-day in electrical science which
7801𓀴would have been deemed unholy by the very men who discovered
7802𓀴electricity--who would themselves not so long before have been burned
7803𓀴as wizards. There are always mysteries in life. Why was it that
7804𓀴Methuselah lived nine hundred years, and ‘Old Parr’ one hundred and
7805𓀴sixty-nine, and yet that poor Lucy, with four men’s blood in her poor
7806𓀴veins, could not live even one day? For, had she live one more day, we
7807𓀴could have save her. Do you know all the mystery of life and death? Do
7808𓀴you know the altogether of comparative anatomy and can say wherefore the
7809𓀴qualities of brutes are in some men, and not in others? Can you tell me
7810𓀴why, when other spiders die small and soon, that one great spider lived
7811𓀴for centuries in the tower of the old Spanish church and grew and grew,
7812𓀴till, on descending, he could drink the oil of all the church lamps? Can
7813𓀴you tell me why in the Pampas, ay and elsewhere, there are bats that
7814𓀴come at night and open the veins of cattle and horses and suck dry their
7815𓀴veins; how in some islands of the Western seas there are bats which hang
7816𓀴on the trees all day, and those who have seen describe as like giant
7817𓀴nuts or pods, and that when the sailors sleep on the deck, because that
7818𓀴it is hot, flit down on them, and then--and then in the morning are
7819𓀴found dead men, white as even Miss Lucy was?”
7820𓀴
7821𓀴“Good God, Professor!” I said, starting up. “Do you mean to tell me that
7822𓀴Lucy was bitten by such a bat; and that such a thing is here in London
7823𓀴in the nineteenth century?” He waved his hand for silence, and went
7824𓀴on:--
7825𓀴
7826𓀴“Can you tell me why the tortoise lives more long than generations of
7827𓀴men; why the elephant goes on and on till he have seen dynasties; and
7828𓀴why the parrot never die only of bite of cat or dog or other complaint?
7829𓀴Can you tell me why men believe in all ages and places that there are
7830𓀴some few who live on always if they be permit; that there are men and
7831𓀴women who cannot die? We all know--because science has vouched for the
7832𓀴fact--that there have been toads shut up in rocks for thousands of
7833𓀴years, shut in one so small hole that only hold him since the youth of
7834𓀴the world. Can you tell me how the Indian fakir can make himself to die
7835𓀴and have been buried, and his grave sealed and corn sowed on it, and the
7836𓀴corn reaped and be cut and sown and reaped and cut again, and then men
7837𓀴come and take away the unbroken seal and that there lie the Indian
7838𓀴fakir, not dead, but that rise up and walk amongst them as before?” Here
7839𓀴I interrupted him. I was getting bewildered; he so crowded on my mind
7840𓀴his list of nature’s eccentricities and possible impossibilities that my
7841𓀴imagination was getting fired. I had a dim idea that he was teaching me
7842𓀴some lesson, as long ago he used to do in his study at Amsterdam; but
7843𓀴he used then to tell me the thing, so that I could have the object of
7844𓀴thought in mind all the time. But now I was without this help, yet I
7845𓀴wanted to follow him, so I said:--
7846𓀴
7847𓀴“Professor, let me be your pet student again. Tell me the thesis, so
7848𓀴that I may apply your knowledge as you go on. At present I am going in
7849𓀴my mind from point to point as a mad man, and not a sane one, follows an
7850𓀴idea. I feel like a novice lumbering through a bog in a mist, jumping
7851𓀴from one tussock to another in the mere blind effort to move on without
7852𓀴knowing where I am going.”
7853𓀴
7854𓀴“That is good image,” he said. “Well, I shall tell you. My thesis is
7855𓀴this: I want you to believe.”
7856𓀴
7857𓀴“To believe what?”
7858𓀴
7859𓀴“To believe in things that you cannot. Let me illustrate. I heard once
7860𓀴of an American who so defined faith: ‘that faculty which enables us to
7861𓀴believe things which we know to be untrue.’ For one, I follow that man.
7862𓀴He meant that we shall have an open mind, and not let a little bit of
7863𓀴truth check the rush of a big truth, like a small rock does a railway
7864𓀴truck. We get the small truth first. Good! We keep him, and we value
7865𓀴him; but all the same we must not let him think himself all the truth in
7866𓀴the universe.”
7867𓀴
7868𓀴“Then you want me not to let some previous conviction injure the
7869𓀴receptivity of my mind with regard to some strange matter. Do I read
7870𓀴your lesson aright?”
7871𓀴
7872𓀴“Ah, you are my favourite pupil still. It is worth to teach you. Now
7873𓀴that you are willing to understand, you have taken the first step to
7874𓀴understand. You think then that those so small holes in the children’s
7875𓀴throats were made by the same that made the hole in Miss Lucy?”
7876𓀴
7877𓀴“I suppose so.” He stood up and said solemnly:--
7878𓀴
7879𓀴“Then you are wrong. Oh, would it were so! but alas! no. It is worse,
7880𓀴far, far worse.”
7881𓀴
7882𓀴“In God’s name, Professor Van Helsing, what do you mean?” I cried.
7883𓀴
7884𓀴He threw himself with a despairing gesture into a chair, and placed his
7885𓀴elbows on the table, covering his face with his hands as he spoke:--
7886𓀴
7887𓀴“They were made by Miss Lucy!”
7888𓀴
7889𓀴
7890𓀴
7891𓀴
7892𓀴CHAPTER XV
7893𓀴
7894𓀴DR. SEWARD’S DIARY--_continued_.
7895𓀴
7896𓀴
7897𓀴For a while sheer anger mastered me; it was as if he had during her life
7898𓀴struck Lucy on the face. I smote the table hard and rose up as I said to
7899𓀴him:--
7900𓀴
7901𓀴“Dr. Van Helsing, are you mad?” He raised his head and looked at me, and
7902𓀴somehow the tenderness of his face calmed me at once. “Would I were!” he
7903𓀴said. “Madness were easy to bear compared with truth like this. Oh, my
7904𓀴friend, why, think you, did I go so far round, why take so long to tell
7905𓀴you so simple a thing? Was it because I hate you and have hated you all
7906𓀴my life? Was it because I wished to give you pain? Was it that I wanted,
7907𓀴now so late, revenge for that time when you saved my life, and from a
7908𓀴fearful death? Ah no!”
7909𓀴
7910𓀴“Forgive me,” said I. He went on:--
7911𓀴
7912𓀴“My friend, it was because I wished to be gentle in the breaking to you,
7913𓀴for I know you have loved that so sweet lady. But even yet I do not
7914𓀴expect you to believe. It is so hard to accept at once any abstract
7915𓀴truth, that we may doubt such to be possible when we have always
7916𓀴believed the ‘no’ of it; it is more hard still to accept so sad a
7917𓀴concrete truth, and of such a one as Miss Lucy. To-night I go to prove
7918𓀴it. Dare you come with me?”
7919𓀴
7920𓀴This staggered me. A man does not like to prove such a truth; Byron
7921𓀴excepted from the category, jealousy.
7922𓀴
7923𓀴    “And prove the very truth he most abhorred.”
7924𓀴
7925𓀴He saw my hesitation, and spoke:--
7926𓀴
7927𓀴“The logic is simple, no madman’s logic this time, jumping from tussock
7928𓀴to tussock in a misty bog. If it be not true, then proof will be relief;
7929𓀴at worst it will not harm. If it be true! Ah, there is the dread; yet
7930𓀴very dread should help my cause, for in it is some need of belief. Come,
7931𓀴I tell you what I propose: first, that we go off now and see that child
7932𓀴in the hospital. Dr. Vincent, of the North Hospital, where the papers
7933𓀴say the child is, is friend of mine, and I think of yours since you were
7934𓀴in class at Amsterdam. He will let two scientists see his case, if he
7935𓀴will not let two friends. We shall tell him nothing, but only that we
7936𓀴wish to learn. And then----”
7937𓀴
7938𓀴“And then?” He took a key from his pocket and held it up. “And then we
7939𓀴spend the night, you and I, in the churchyard where Lucy lies. This is
7940𓀴the key that lock the tomb. I had it from the coffin-man to give to
7941𓀴Arthur.” My heart sank within me, for I felt that there was some fearful
7942𓀴ordeal before us. I could do nothing, however, so I plucked up what
7943𓀴heart I could and said that we had better hasten, as the afternoon was
7944𓀴passing....
7945𓀴
7946𓀴We found the child awake. It had had a sleep and taken some food, and
7947𓀴altogether was going on well. Dr. Vincent took the bandage from its
7948𓀴throat, and showed us the punctures. There was no mistaking the
7949𓀴similarity to those which had been on Lucy’s throat. They were smaller,
7950𓀴and the edges looked fresher; that was all. We asked Vincent to what he
7951𓀴attributed them, and he replied that it must have been a bite of some
7952𓀴animal, perhaps a rat; but, for his own part, he was inclined to think
7953𓀴that it was one of the bats which are so numerous on the northern
7954𓀴heights of London. “Out of so many harmless ones,” he said, “there may
7955𓀴be some wild specimen from the South of a more malignant species. Some
7956𓀴sailor may have brought one home, and it managed to escape; or even from
7957𓀴the Zoölogical Gardens a young one may have got loose, or one be bred
7958𓀴there from a vampire. These things do occur, you know. Only ten days ago
7959𓀴a wolf got out, and was, I believe, traced up in this direction. For a
7960𓀴week after, the children were playing nothing but Red Riding Hood on the
7961𓀴Heath and in every alley in the place until this ‘bloofer lady’ scare
7962𓀴came along, since when it has been quite a gala-time with them. Even
7963𓀴this poor little mite, when he woke up to-day, asked the nurse if he
7964𓀴might go away. When she asked him why he wanted to go, he said he wanted
7965𓀴to play with the ‘bloofer lady.’”
7966𓀴
7967𓀴“I hope,” said Van Helsing, “that when you are sending the child home
7968𓀴you will caution its parents to keep strict watch over it. These fancies
7969𓀴to stray are most dangerous; and if the child were to remain out another
7970𓀴night, it would probably be fatal. But in any case I suppose you will
7971𓀴not let it away for some days?”
7972𓀴
7973𓀴“Certainly not, not for a week at least; longer if the wound is not
7974𓀴healed.”
7975𓀴
7976𓀴Our visit to the hospital took more time than we had reckoned on, and
7977𓀴the sun had dipped before we came out. When Van Helsing saw how dark it
7978𓀴was, he said:--
7979𓀴
7980𓀴“There is no hurry. It is more late than I thought. Come, let us seek
7981𓀴somewhere that we may eat, and then we shall go on our way.”
7982𓀴
7983𓀴We dined at “Jack Straw’s Castle” along with a little crowd of
7984𓀴bicyclists and others who were genially noisy. About ten o’clock we
7985𓀴started from the inn. It was then very dark, and the scattered lamps
7986𓀴made the darkness greater when we were once outside their individual
7987𓀴radius. The Professor had evidently noted the road we were to go, for he
7988𓀴went on unhesitatingly; but, as for me, I was in quite a mixup as to
7989𓀴locality. As we went further, we met fewer and fewer people, till at
7990𓀴last we were somewhat surprised when we met even the patrol of horse
7991𓀴police going their usual suburban round. At last we reached the wall of
7992𓀴the churchyard, which we climbed over. With some little difficulty--for
7993𓀴it was very dark, and the whole place seemed so strange to us--we found
7994𓀴the Westenra tomb. The Professor took the key, opened the creaky door,
7995𓀴and standing back, politely, but quite unconsciously, motioned me to
7996𓀴precede him. There was a delicious irony in the offer, in the
7997𓀴courtliness of giving preference on such a ghastly occasion. My
7998𓀴companion followed me quickly, and cautiously drew the door to, after
7999𓀴carefully ascertaining that the lock was a falling, and not a spring,
8000𓀴one. In the latter case we should have been in a bad plight. Then he
8001𓀴fumbled in his bag, and taking out a matchbox and a piece of candle,
8002𓀴proceeded to make a light. The tomb in the day-time, and when wreathed
8003𓀴with fresh flowers, had looked grim and gruesome enough; but now, some
8004𓀴days afterwards, when the flowers hung lank and dead, their whites
8005𓀴turning to rust and their greens to browns; when the spider and the
8006𓀴beetle had resumed their accustomed dominance; when time-discoloured
8007𓀴stone, and dust-encrusted mortar, and rusty, dank iron, and tarnished
8008𓀴brass, and clouded silver-plating gave back the feeble glimmer of a
8009𓀴candle, the effect was more miserable and sordid than could have been
8010𓀴imagined. It conveyed irresistibly the idea that life--animal life--was
8011𓀴not the only thing which could pass away.
8012𓀴
8013𓀴Van Helsing went about his work systematically. Holding his candle so
8014𓀴that he could read the coffin plates, and so holding it that the sperm
8015𓀴dropped in white patches which congealed as they touched the metal, he
8016𓀴made assurance of Lucy’s coffin. Another search in his bag, and he took
8017𓀴out a turnscrew.
8018𓀴
8019𓀴“What are you going to do?” I asked.
8020𓀴
8021𓀴“To open the coffin. You shall yet be convinced.” Straightway he began
8022𓀴taking out the screws, and finally lifted off the lid, showing the
8023𓀴casing of lead beneath. The sight was almost too much for me. It seemed
8024𓀴to be as much an affront to the dead as it would have been to have
8025𓀴stripped off her clothing in her sleep whilst living; I actually took
8026𓀴hold of his hand to stop him. He only said: “You shall see,” and again
8027𓀴fumbling in his bag, took out a tiny fret-saw. Striking the turnscrew
8028𓀴through the lead with a swift downward stab, which made me wince, he
8029𓀴made a small hole, which was, however, big enough to admit the point of
8030𓀴the saw. I had expected a rush of gas from the week-old corpse. We
8031𓀴doctors, who have had to study our dangers, have to become accustomed to
8032𓀴such things, and I drew back towards the door. But the Professor never
8033𓀴stopped for a moment; he sawed down a couple of feet along one side of
8034𓀴the lead coffin, and then across, and down the other side. Taking the
8035𓀴edge of the loose flange, he bent it back towards the foot of the
8036𓀴coffin, and holding up the candle into the aperture, motioned to me to
8037𓀴look.
8038𓀴
8039𓀴I drew near and looked. The coffin was empty.
8040𓀴
8041𓀴It was certainly a surprise to me, and gave me a considerable shock, but
8042𓀴Van Helsing was unmoved. He was now more sure than ever of his ground,
8043𓀴and so emboldened to proceed in his task. “Are you satisfied now, friend
8044𓀴John?” he asked.
8045𓀴
8046𓀴I felt all the dogged argumentativeness of my nature awake within me as
8047𓀴I answered him:--
8048𓀴
8049𓀴“I am satisfied that Lucy’s body is not in that coffin; but that only
8050𓀴proves one thing.”
8051𓀴
8052𓀴“And what is that, friend John?”
8053𓀴
8054𓀴“That it is not there.”
8055𓀴
8056𓀴“That is good logic,” he said, “so far as it goes. But how do you--how
8057𓀴can you--account for it not being there?”
8058𓀴
8059𓀴“Perhaps a body-snatcher,” I suggested. “Some of the undertaker’s people
8060𓀴may have stolen it.” I felt that I was speaking folly, and yet it was
8061𓀴the only real cause which I could suggest. The Professor sighed. “Ah
8062𓀴well!” he said, “we must have more proof. Come with me.”
8063𓀴
8064𓀴He put on the coffin-lid again, gathered up all his things and placed
8065𓀴them in the bag, blew out the light, and placed the candle also in the
8066𓀴bag. We opened the door, and went out. Behind us he closed the door and
8067𓀴locked it. He handed me the key, saying: “Will you keep it? You had
8068𓀴better be assured.” I laughed--it was not a very cheerful laugh, I am
8069𓀴bound to say--as I motioned him to keep it. “A key is nothing,” I said;
8070𓀴“there may be duplicates; and anyhow it is not difficult to pick a lock
8071𓀴of that kind.” He said nothing, but put the key in his pocket. Then he
8072𓀴told me to watch at one side of the churchyard whilst he would watch at
8073𓀴the other. I took up my place behind a yew-tree, and I saw his dark
8074𓀴figure move until the intervening headstones and trees hid it from my
8075𓀴sight.
8076𓀴
8077𓀴It was a lonely vigil. Just after I had taken my place I heard a distant
8078𓀴clock strike twelve, and in time came one and two. I was chilled and
8079𓀴unnerved, and angry with the Professor for taking me on such an errand
8080𓀴and with myself for coming. I was too cold and too sleepy to be keenly
8081𓀴observant, and not sleepy enough to betray my trust so altogether I had
8082𓀴a dreary, miserable time.
8083𓀴
8084𓀴Suddenly, as I turned round, I thought I saw something like a white
8085𓀴streak, moving between two dark yew-trees at the side of the churchyard
8086𓀴farthest from the tomb; at the same time a dark mass moved from the
8087𓀴Professor’s side of the ground, and hurriedly went towards it. Then I
8088𓀴too moved; but I had to go round headstones and railed-off tombs, and I
8089𓀴stumbled over graves. The sky was overcast, and somewhere far off an
8090𓀴early cock crew. A little way off, beyond a line of scattered
8091𓀴juniper-trees, which marked the pathway to the church, a white, dim
8092𓀴figure flitted in the direction of the tomb. The tomb itself was hidden
8093𓀴by trees, and I could not see where the figure disappeared. I heard the
8094𓀴rustle of actual movement where I had first seen the white figure, and
8095𓀴coming over, found the Professor holding in his arms a tiny child. When
8096𓀴he saw me he held it out to me, and said:--
8097𓀴
8098𓀴“Are you satisfied now?”
8099𓀴
8100𓀴“No,” I said, in a way that I felt was aggressive.
8101𓀴
8102𓀴“Do you not see the child?”
8103𓀴
8104𓀴“Yes, it is a child, but who brought it here? And is it wounded?” I
8105𓀴asked.
8106𓀴
8107𓀴“We shall see,” said the Professor, and with one impulse we took our way
8108𓀴out of the churchyard, he carrying the sleeping child.
8109𓀴
8110𓀴When we had got some little distance away, we went into a clump of
8111𓀴trees, and struck a match, and looked at the child’s throat. It was
8112𓀴without a scratch or scar of any kind.
8113𓀴
8114𓀴“Was I right?” I asked triumphantly.
8115𓀴
8116𓀴“We were just in time,” said the Professor thankfully.
8117𓀴
8118𓀴We had now to decide what we were to do with the child, and so consulted
8119𓀴about it. If we were to take it to a police-station we should have to
8120𓀴give some account of our movements during the night; at least, we should
8121𓀴have had to make some statement as to how we had come to find the child.
8122𓀴So finally we decided that we would take it to the Heath, and when we
8123𓀴heard a policeman coming, would leave it where he could not fail to find
8124𓀴it; we would then seek our way home as quickly as we could. All fell out
8125𓀴well. At the edge of Hampstead Heath we heard a policeman’s heavy
8126𓀴tramp, and laying the child on the pathway, we waited and watched until
8127𓀴he saw it as he flashed his lantern to and fro. We heard his exclamation
8128𓀴of astonishment, and then we went away silently. By good chance we got a
8129𓀴cab near the “Spaniards,” and drove to town.
8130𓀴
8131𓀴I cannot sleep, so I make this entry. But I must try to get a few hours’
8132𓀴sleep, as Van Helsing is to call for me at noon. He insists that I shall
8133𓀴go with him on another expedition.
8134𓀴
8135𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
8136𓀴
8137𓀴_27 September._--It was two o’clock before we found a suitable
8138𓀴opportunity for our attempt. The funeral held at noon was all completed,
8139𓀴and the last stragglers of the mourners had taken themselves lazily
8140𓀴away, when, looking carefully from behind a clump of alder-trees, we saw
8141𓀴the sexton lock the gate after him. We knew then that we were safe till
8142𓀴morning did we desire it; but the Professor told me that we should not
8143𓀴want more than an hour at most. Again I felt that horrid sense of the
8144𓀴reality of things, in which any effort of imagination seemed out of
8145𓀴place; and I realised distinctly the perils of the law which we were
8146𓀴incurring in our unhallowed work. Besides, I felt it was all so useless.
8147𓀴Outrageous as it was to open a leaden coffin, to see if a woman dead
8148𓀴nearly a week were really dead, it now seemed the height of folly to
8149𓀴open the tomb again, when we knew, from the evidence of our own
8150𓀴eyesight, that the coffin was empty. I shrugged my shoulders, however,
8151𓀴and rested silent, for Van Helsing had a way of going on his own road,
8152𓀴no matter who remonstrated. He took the key, opened the vault, and again
8153𓀴courteously motioned me to precede. The place was not so gruesome as
8154𓀴last night, but oh, how unutterably mean-looking when the sunshine
8155𓀴streamed in. Van Helsing walked over to Lucy’s coffin, and I followed.
8156𓀴He bent over and again forced back the leaden flange; and then a shock
8157𓀴of surprise and dismay shot through me.
8158𓀴
