8159𓀴There lay Lucy, seemingly just as we had seen her the night before her
8160𓀴funeral. She was, if possible, more radiantly beautiful than ever; and I
8161𓀴could not believe that she was dead. The lips were red, nay redder than
8162𓀴before; and on the cheeks was a delicate bloom.
8163𓀴
8164𓀴“Is this a juggle?” I said to him.
8165𓀴
8166𓀴“Are you convinced now?” said the Professor in response, and as he spoke
8167𓀴he put over his hand, and in a way that made me shudder, pulled back the
8168𓀴dead lips and showed the white teeth.
8169𓀴
8170𓀴“See,” he went on, “see, they are even sharper than before. With this
8171𓀴and this”--and he touched one of the canine teeth and that below
8172𓀴it--“the little children can be bitten. Are you of belief now, friend
8173𓀴John?” Once more, argumentative hostility woke within me. I _could_ not
8174𓀴accept such an overwhelming idea as he suggested; so, with an attempt to
8175𓀴argue of which I was even at the moment ashamed, I said:--
8176𓀴
8177𓀴“She may have been placed here since last night.”
8178𓀴
8179𓀴“Indeed? That is so, and by whom?”
8180𓀴
8181𓀴“I do not know. Some one has done it.”
8182𓀴
8183𓀴“And yet she has been dead one week. Most peoples in that time would not
8184𓀴look so.” I had no answer for this, so was silent. Van Helsing did not
8185𓀴seem to notice my silence; at any rate, he showed neither chagrin nor
8186𓀴triumph. He was looking intently at the face of the dead woman, raising
8187𓀴the eyelids and looking at the eyes, and once more opening the lips and
8188𓀴examining the teeth. Then he turned to me and said:--
8189𓀴
8190𓀴“Here, there is one thing which is different from all recorded; here is
8191𓀴some dual life that is not as the common. She was bitten by the vampire
8192𓀴when she was in a trance, sleep-walking--oh, you start; you do not know
8193𓀴that, friend John, but you shall know it all later--and in trance could
8194𓀴he best come to take more blood. In trance she died, and in trance she
8195𓀴is Un-Dead, too. So it is that she differ from all other. Usually when
8196𓀴the Un-Dead sleep at home”--as he spoke he made a comprehensive sweep of
8197𓀴his arm to designate what to a vampire was “home”--“their face show what
8198𓀴they are, but this so sweet that was when she not Un-Dead she go back to
8199𓀴the nothings of the common dead. There is no malign there, see, and so
8200𓀴it make hard that I must kill her in her sleep.” This turned my blood
8201𓀴cold, and it began to dawn upon me that I was accepting Van Helsing’s
8202𓀴theories; but if she were really dead, what was there of terror in the
8203𓀴idea of killing her? He looked up at me, and evidently saw the change in
8204𓀴my face, for he said almost joyously:--
8205𓀴
8206𓀴“Ah, you believe now?”
8207𓀴
8208𓀴I answered: “Do not press me too hard all at once. I am willing to
8209𓀴accept. How will you do this bloody work?”
8210𓀴
8211𓀴“I shall cut off her head and fill her mouth with garlic, and I shall
8212𓀴drive a stake through her body.” It made me shudder to think of so
8213𓀴mutilating the body of the woman whom I had loved. And yet the feeling
8214𓀴was not so strong as I had expected. I was, in fact, beginning to
8215𓀴shudder at the presence of this being, this Un-Dead, as Van Helsing
8216𓀴called it, and to loathe it. Is it possible that love is all subjective,
8217𓀴or all objective?
8218𓀴
8219𓀴I waited a considerable time for Van Helsing to begin, but he stood as
8220𓀴if wrapped in thought. Presently he closed the catch of his bag with a
8221𓀴snap, and said:--
8222𓀴
8223𓀴“I have been thinking, and have made up my mind as to what is best. If I
8224𓀴did simply follow my inclining I would do now, at this moment, what is
8225𓀴to be done; but there are other things to follow, and things that are
8226𓀴thousand times more difficult in that them we do not know. This is
8227𓀴simple. She have yet no life taken, though that is of time; and to act
8228𓀴now would be to take danger from her for ever. But then we may have to
8229𓀴want Arthur, and how shall we tell him of this? If you, who saw the
8230𓀴wounds on Lucy’s throat, and saw the wounds so similar on the child’s at
8231𓀴the hospital; if you, who saw the coffin empty last night and full
8232𓀴to-day with a woman who have not change only to be more rose and more
8233𓀴beautiful in a whole week, after she die--if you know of this and know
8234𓀴of the white figure last night that brought the child to the churchyard,
8235𓀴and yet of your own senses you did not believe, how, then, can I expect
8236𓀴Arthur, who know none of those things, to believe? He doubted me when I
8237𓀴took him from her kiss when she was dying. I know he has forgiven me
8238𓀴because in some mistaken idea I have done things that prevent him say
8239𓀴good-bye as he ought; and he may think that in some more mistaken idea
8240𓀴this woman was buried alive; and that in most mistake of all we have
8241𓀴killed her. He will then argue back that it is we, mistaken ones, that
8242𓀴have killed her by our ideas; and so he will be much unhappy always. Yet
8243𓀴he never can be sure; and that is the worst of all. And he will
8244𓀴sometimes think that she he loved was buried alive, and that will paint
8245𓀴his dreams with horrors of what she must have suffered; and again, he
8246𓀴will think that we may be right, and that his so beloved was, after all,
8247𓀴an Un-Dead. No! I told him once, and since then I learn much. Now, since
8248𓀴I know it is all true, a hundred thousand times more do I know that he
8249𓀴must pass through the bitter waters to reach the sweet. He, poor fellow,
8250𓀴must have one hour that will make the very face of heaven grow black to
8251𓀴him; then we can act for good all round and send him peace. My mind is
8252𓀴made up. Let us go. You return home for to-night to your asylum, and see
8253𓀴that all be well. As for me, I shall spend the night here in this
8254𓀴churchyard in my own way. To-morrow night you will come to me to the
8255𓀴Berkeley Hotel at ten of the clock. I shall send for Arthur to come too,
8256𓀴and also that so fine young man of America that gave his blood. Later we
8257𓀴shall all have work to do. I come with you so far as Piccadilly and
8258𓀴there dine, for I must be back here before the sun set.”
8259𓀴
8260𓀴So we locked the tomb and came away, and got over the wall of the
8261𓀴churchyard, which was not much of a task, and drove back to Piccadilly.
8262𓀴
8263𓀴
8264𓀴_Note left by Van Helsing in his portmanteau, Berkeley Hotel directed to
8265𓀴John Seward, M. D._
8266𓀴
8267𓀴(Not delivered.)
8268𓀴
8269𓀴“_27 September._
8270𓀴
8271𓀴“Friend John,--
8272𓀴
8273𓀴“I write this in case anything should happen. I go alone to watch in
8274𓀴that churchyard. It pleases me that the Un-Dead, Miss Lucy, shall not
8275𓀴leave to-night, that so on the morrow night she may be more eager.
8276𓀴Therefore I shall fix some things she like not--garlic and a
8277𓀴crucifix--and so seal up the door of the tomb. She is young as Un-Dead,
8278𓀴and will heed. Moreover, these are only to prevent her coming out; they
8279𓀴may not prevail on her wanting to get in; for then the Un-Dead is
8280𓀴desperate, and must find the line of least resistance, whatsoever it may
8281𓀴be. I shall be at hand all the night from sunset till after the sunrise,
8282𓀴and if there be aught that may be learned I shall learn it. For Miss
8283𓀴Lucy or from her, I have no fear; but that other to whom is there that
8284𓀴she is Un-Dead, he have now the power to seek her tomb and find shelter.
8285𓀴He is cunning, as I know from Mr. Jonathan and from the way that all
8286𓀴along he have fooled us when he played with us for Miss Lucy’s life, and
8287𓀴we lost; and in many ways the Un-Dead are strong. He have always the
8288𓀴strength in his hand of twenty men; even we four who gave our strength
8289𓀴to Miss Lucy it also is all to him. Besides, he can summon his wolf and
8290𓀴I know not what. So if it be that he come thither on this night he shall
8291𓀴find me; but none other shall--until it be too late. But it may be that
8292𓀴he will not attempt the place. There is no reason why he should; his
8293𓀴hunting ground is more full of game than the churchyard where the
8294𓀴Un-Dead woman sleep, and the one old man watch.
8295𓀴
8296𓀴“Therefore I write this in case.... Take the papers that are with this,
8297𓀴the diaries of Harker and the rest, and read them, and then find this
8298𓀴great Un-Dead, and cut off his head and burn his heart or drive a stake
8299𓀴through it, so that the world may rest from him.
8300𓀴
8301𓀴“If it be so, farewell.
8302𓀴
8303𓀴“VAN HELSING.”
8304𓀴
8305𓀴
8306𓀴
8307𓀴_Dr. Seward’s Diary._
8308𓀴
8309𓀴_28 September._--It is wonderful what a good night’s sleep will do for
8310𓀴one. Yesterday I was almost willing to accept Van Helsing’s monstrous
8311𓀴ideas; but now they seem to start out lurid before me as outrages on
8312𓀴common sense. I have no doubt that he believes it all. I wonder if his
8313𓀴mind can have become in any way unhinged. Surely there must be _some_
8314𓀴rational explanation of all these mysterious things. Is it possible that
8315𓀴the Professor can have done it himself? He is so abnormally clever that
8316𓀴if he went off his head he would carry out his intent with regard to
8317𓀴some fixed idea in a wonderful way. I am loath to think it, and indeed
8318𓀴it would be almost as great a marvel as the other to find that Van
8319𓀴Helsing was mad; but anyhow I shall watch him carefully. I may get some
8320𓀴light on the mystery.
8321𓀴
8322𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
8323𓀴
8324𓀴_29 September, morning._.... Last night, at a little before ten o’clock,
8325𓀴Arthur and Quincey came into Van Helsing’s room; he told us all that he
8326𓀴wanted us to do, but especially addressing himself to Arthur, as if all
8327𓀴our wills were centred in his. He began by saying that he hoped we would
8328𓀴all come with him too, “for,” he said, “there is a grave duty to be done
8329𓀴there. You were doubtless surprised at my letter?” This query was
8330𓀴directly addressed to Lord Godalming.
8331𓀴
8332𓀴“I was. It rather upset me for a bit. There has been so much trouble
8333𓀴around my house of late that I could do without any more. I have been
8334𓀴curious, too, as to what you mean. Quincey and I talked it over; but the
8335𓀴more we talked, the more puzzled we got, till now I can say for myself
8336𓀴that I’m about up a tree as to any meaning about anything.”
8337𓀴
8338𓀴“Me too,” said Quincey Morris laconically.
8339𓀴
8340𓀴“Oh,” said the Professor, “then you are nearer the beginning, both of
8341𓀴you, than friend John here, who has to go a long way back before he can
8342𓀴even get so far as to begin.”
8343𓀴
8344𓀴It was evident that he recognised my return to my old doubting frame of
8345𓀴mind without my saying a word. Then, turning to the other two, he said
8346𓀴with intense gravity:--
8347𓀴
8348𓀴“I want your permission to do what I think good this night. It is, I
8349𓀴know, much to ask; and when you know what it is I propose to do you will
8350𓀴know, and only then, how much. Therefore may I ask that you promise me
8351𓀴in the dark, so that afterwards, though you may be angry with me for a
8352𓀴time--I must not disguise from myself the possibility that such may
8353𓀴be--you shall not blame yourselves for anything.”
8354𓀴
8355𓀴“That’s frank anyhow,” broke in Quincey. “I’ll answer for the Professor.
8356𓀴I don’t quite see his drift, but I swear he’s honest; and that’s good
8357𓀴enough for me.”
8358𓀴
8359𓀴“I thank you, sir,” said Van Helsing proudly. “I have done myself the
8360𓀴honour of counting you one trusting friend, and such endorsement is dear
8361𓀴to me.” He held out a hand, which Quincey took.
8362𓀴
8363𓀴Then Arthur spoke out:--
8364𓀴
8365𓀴“Dr. Van Helsing, I don’t quite like to ‘buy a pig in a poke,’ as they
8366𓀴say in Scotland, and if it be anything in which my honour as a gentleman
8367𓀴or my faith as a Christian is concerned, I cannot make such a promise.
8368𓀴If you can assure me that what you intend does not violate either of
8369𓀴these two, then I give my consent at once; though for the life of me, I
8370𓀴cannot understand what you are driving at.”
8371𓀴
8372𓀴“I accept your limitation,” said Van Helsing, “and all I ask of you is
8373𓀴that if you feel it necessary to condemn any act of mine, you will first
8374𓀴consider it well and be satisfied that it does not violate your
8375𓀴reservations.”
8376𓀴
8377𓀴“Agreed!” said Arthur; “that is only fair. And now that the
8378𓀴_pourparlers_ are over, may I ask what it is we are to do?”
8379𓀴
8380𓀴“I want you to come with me, and to come in secret, to the churchyard at
8381𓀴Kingstead.”
8382𓀴
8383𓀴Arthur’s face fell as he said in an amazed sort of way:--
8384𓀴
8385𓀴“Where poor Lucy is buried?” The Professor bowed. Arthur went on: “And
8386𓀴when there?”
8387𓀴
8388𓀴“To enter the tomb!” Arthur stood up.
8389𓀴
8390𓀴“Professor, are you in earnest; or it is some monstrous joke? Pardon me,
8391𓀴I see that you are in earnest.” He sat down again, but I could see that
8392𓀴he sat firmly and proudly, as one who is on his dignity. There was
8393𓀴silence until he asked again:--
8394𓀴
8395𓀴“And when in the tomb?”
8396𓀴
8397𓀴“To open the coffin.”
8398𓀴
8399𓀴“This is too much!” he said, angrily rising again. “I am willing to be
8400𓀴patient in all things that are reasonable; but in this--this desecration
8401𓀴of the grave--of one who----” He fairly choked with indignation. The
8402𓀴Professor looked pityingly at him.
8403𓀴
8404𓀴“If I could spare you one pang, my poor friend,” he said, “God knows I
8405𓀴would. But this night our feet must tread in thorny paths; or later, and
8406𓀴for ever, the feet you love must walk in paths of flame!”
8407𓀴
8408𓀴Arthur looked up with set white face and said:--
8409𓀴
8410𓀴“Take care, sir, take care!”
8411𓀴
8412𓀴“Would it not be well to hear what I have to say?” said Van Helsing.
8413𓀴“And then you will at least know the limit of my purpose. Shall I go
8414𓀴on?”
8415𓀴
8416𓀴“That’s fair enough,” broke in Morris.
8417𓀴
8418𓀴After a pause Van Helsing went on, evidently with an effort:--
8419𓀴
8420𓀴“Miss Lucy is dead; is it not so? Yes! Then there can be no wrong to
8421𓀴her. But if she be not dead----”
8422𓀴
8423𓀴Arthur jumped to his feet.
8424𓀴
8425𓀴“Good God!” he cried. “What do you mean? Has there been any mistake; has
8426𓀴she been buried alive?” He groaned in anguish that not even hope could
8427𓀴soften.
8428𓀴
8429𓀴“I did not say she was alive, my child; I did not think it. I go no
8430𓀴further than to say that she might be Un-Dead.”
8431𓀴
8432𓀴“Un-Dead! Not alive! What do you mean? Is this all a nightmare, or what
8433𓀴is it?”
8434𓀴
8435𓀴“There are mysteries which men can only guess at, which age by age they
8436𓀴may solve only in part. Believe me, we are now on the verge of one. But
8437𓀴I have not done. May I cut off the head of dead Miss Lucy?”
8438𓀴
8439𓀴“Heavens and earth, no!” cried Arthur in a storm of passion. “Not for
8440𓀴the wide world will I consent to any mutilation of her dead body. Dr.
8441𓀴Van Helsing, you try me too far. What have I done to you that you should
8442𓀴torture me so? What did that poor, sweet girl do that you should want to
8443𓀴cast such dishonour on her grave? Are you mad to speak such things, or
8444𓀴am I mad to listen to them? Don’t dare to think more of such a
8445𓀴desecration; I shall not give my consent to anything you do. I have a
8446𓀴duty to do in protecting her grave from outrage; and, by God, I shall do
8447𓀴it!”
8448𓀴
8449𓀴Van Helsing rose up from where he had all the time been seated, and
8450𓀴said, gravely and sternly:--
8451𓀴
8452𓀴“My Lord Godalming, I, too, have a duty to do, a duty to others, a duty
8453𓀴to you, a duty to the dead; and, by God, I shall do it! All I ask you
8454𓀴now is that you come with me, that you look and listen; and if when
8455𓀴later I make the same request you do not be more eager for its
8456𓀴fulfilment even than I am, then--then I shall do my duty, whatever it
8457𓀴may seem to me. And then, to follow of your Lordship’s wishes I shall
8458𓀴hold myself at your disposal to render an account to you, when and where
8459𓀴you will.” His voice broke a little, and he went on with a voice full of
8460𓀴pity:--
8461𓀴
8462𓀴“But, I beseech you, do not go forth in anger with me. In a long life of
8463𓀴acts which were often not pleasant to do, and which sometimes did wring
8464𓀴my heart, I have never had so heavy a task as now. Believe me that if
8465𓀴the time comes for you to change your mind towards me, one look from
8466𓀴you will wipe away all this so sad hour, for I would do what a man can
8467𓀴to save you from sorrow. Just think. For why should I give myself so
8468𓀴much of labour and so much of sorrow? I have come here from my own land
8469𓀴to do what I can of good; at the first to please my friend John, and
8470𓀴then to help a sweet young lady, whom, too, I came to love. For her--I
8471𓀴am ashamed to say so much, but I say it in kindness--I gave what you
8472𓀴gave; the blood of my veins; I gave it, I, who was not, like you, her
8473𓀴lover, but only her physician and her friend. I gave to her my nights
8474𓀴and days--before death, after death; and if my death can do her good
8475𓀴even now, when she is the dead Un-Dead, she shall have it freely.” He
8476𓀴said this with a very grave, sweet pride, and Arthur was much affected
8477𓀴by it. He took the old man’s hand and said in a broken voice:--
8478𓀴
8479𓀴“Oh, it is hard to think of it, and I cannot understand; but at least I
8480𓀴shall go with you and wait.”
8481𓀴
8482𓀴
8483𓀴
8484𓀴
8485𓀴CHAPTER XVI
8486𓀴
8487𓀴DR. SEWARD’S DIARY--_continued_
8488𓀴
8489𓀴
8490𓀴It was just a quarter before twelve o’clock when we got into the
8491𓀴churchyard over the low wall. The night was dark with occasional gleams
8492𓀴of moonlight between the rents of the heavy clouds that scudded across
8493𓀴the sky. We all kept somehow close together, with Van Helsing slightly
8494𓀴in front as he led the way. When we had come close to the tomb I looked
8495𓀴well at Arthur, for I feared that the proximity to a place laden with so
8496𓀴sorrowful a memory would upset him; but he bore himself well. I took it
8497𓀴that the very mystery of the proceeding was in some way a counteractant
8498𓀴to his grief. The Professor unlocked the door, and seeing a natural
8499𓀴hesitation amongst us for various reasons, solved the difficulty by
8500𓀴entering first himself. The rest of us followed, and he closed the door.
8501𓀴He then lit a dark lantern and pointed to the coffin. Arthur stepped
8502𓀴forward hesitatingly; Van Helsing said to me:--
8503𓀴
8504𓀴“You were with me here yesterday. Was the body of Miss Lucy in that
8505𓀴coffin?”
8506𓀴
8507𓀴“It was.” The Professor turned to the rest saying:--
8508𓀴
8509𓀴“You hear; and yet there is no one who does not believe with me.” He
8510𓀴took his screwdriver and again took off the lid of the coffin. Arthur
8511𓀴looked on, very pale but silent; when the lid was removed he stepped
8512𓀴forward. He evidently did not know that there was a leaden coffin, or,
8513𓀴at any rate, had not thought of it. When he saw the rent in the lead,
8514𓀴the blood rushed to his face for an instant, but as quickly fell away
8515𓀴again, so that he remained of a ghastly whiteness; he was still silent.
8516𓀴Van Helsing forced back the leaden flange, and we all looked in and
8517𓀴recoiled.
8518𓀴
8519𓀴The coffin was empty!
8520𓀴
8521𓀴For several minutes no one spoke a word. The silence was broken by
8522𓀴Quincey Morris:--
8523𓀴
8524𓀴“Professor, I answered for you. Your word is all I want. I wouldn’t ask
8525𓀴such a thing ordinarily--I wouldn’t so dishonour you as to imply a
8526𓀴doubt; but this is a mystery that goes beyond any honour or dishonour.
8527𓀴Is this your doing?”
8528𓀴
8529𓀴“I swear to you by all that I hold sacred that I have not removed nor
8530𓀴touched her. What happened was this: Two nights ago my friend Seward and
8531𓀴I came here--with good purpose, believe me. I opened that coffin, which
8532𓀴was then sealed up, and we found it, as now, empty. We then waited, and
8533𓀴saw something white come through the trees. The next day we came here in
8534𓀴day-time, and she lay there. Did she not, friend John?”
8535𓀴
8536𓀴“Yes.”
8537𓀴
8538𓀴“That night we were just in time. One more so small child was missing,
8539𓀴and we find it, thank God, unharmed amongst the graves. Yesterday I came
8540𓀴here before sundown, for at sundown the Un-Dead can move. I waited here
8541𓀴all the night till the sun rose, but I saw nothing. It was most probable
8542𓀴that it was because I had laid over the clamps of those doors garlic,
8543𓀴which the Un-Dead cannot bear, and other things which they shun. Last
8544𓀴night there was no exodus, so to-night before the sundown I took away my
8545𓀴garlic and other things. And so it is we find this coffin empty. But
8546𓀴bear with me. So far there is much that is strange. Wait you with me
8547𓀴outside, unseen and unheard, and things much stranger are yet to be.
8548𓀴So”--here he shut the dark slide of his lantern--“now to the outside.”
8549𓀴He opened the door, and we filed out, he coming last and locking the
8550𓀴door behind him.
8551𓀴
8552𓀴Oh! but it seemed fresh and pure in the night air after the terror of
8553𓀴that vault. How sweet it was to see the clouds race by, and the passing
8554𓀴gleams of the moonlight between the scudding clouds crossing and
8555𓀴passing--like the gladness and sorrow of a man’s life; how sweet it was
8556𓀴to breathe the fresh air, that had no taint of death and decay; how
8557𓀴humanising to see the red lighting of the sky beyond the hill, and to
8558𓀴hear far away the muffled roar that marks the life of a great city. Each
8559𓀴in his own way was solemn and overcome. Arthur was silent, and was, I
8560𓀴could see, striving to grasp the purpose and the inner meaning of the
8561𓀴mystery. I was myself tolerably patient, and half inclined again to
8562𓀴throw aside doubt and to accept Van Helsing’s conclusions. Quincey
8563𓀴Morris was phlegmatic in the way of a man who accepts all things, and
8564𓀴accepts them in the spirit of cool bravery, with hazard of all he has to
8565𓀴stake. Not being able to smoke, he cut himself a good-sized plug of
8566𓀴tobacco and began to chew. As to Van Helsing, he was employed in a
8567𓀴definite way. First he took from his bag a mass of what looked like
8568𓀴thin, wafer-like biscuit, which was carefully rolled up in a white
8569𓀴napkin; next he took out a double-handful of some whitish stuff, like
8570𓀴dough or putty. He crumbled the wafer up fine and worked it into the
8571𓀴mass between his hands. This he then took, and rolling it into thin
8572𓀴strips, began to lay them into the crevices between the door and its
8573𓀴setting in the tomb. I was somewhat puzzled at this, and being close,
8574𓀴asked him what it was that he was doing. Arthur and Quincey drew near
8575𓀴also, as they too were curious. He answered:--
8576𓀴
8577𓀴“I am closing the tomb, so that the Un-Dead may not enter.”
8578𓀴
8579𓀴“And is that stuff you have put there going to do it?” asked Quincey.
8580𓀴“Great Scott! Is this a game?”
8581𓀴
8582𓀴“It is.”
8583𓀴
8584𓀴“What is that which you are using?” This time the question was by
8585𓀴Arthur. Van Helsing reverently lifted his hat as he answered:--
8586𓀴
8587𓀴“The Host. I brought it from Amsterdam. I have an Indulgence.” It was an
8588𓀴answer that appalled the most sceptical of us, and we felt individually
8589𓀴that in the presence of such earnest purpose as the Professor’s, a
8590𓀴purpose which could thus use the to him most sacred of things, it was
8591𓀴impossible to distrust. In respectful silence we took the places
8592𓀴assigned to us close round the tomb, but hidden from the sight of any
8593𓀴one approaching. I pitied the others, especially Arthur. I had myself
8594𓀴been apprenticed by my former visits to this watching horror; and yet I,
8595𓀴who had up to an hour ago repudiated the proofs, felt my heart sink
8596𓀴within me. Never did tombs look so ghastly white; never did cypress, or
8597𓀴yew, or juniper so seem the embodiment of funereal gloom; never did tree
8598𓀴or grass wave or rustle so ominously; never did bough creak so
8599𓀴mysteriously; and never did the far-away howling of dogs send such a
8600𓀴woeful presage through the night.
8601𓀴
8602𓀴There was a long spell of silence, a big, aching void, and then from the
8603𓀴Professor a keen “S-s-s-s!” He pointed; and far down the avenue of yews
8604𓀴we saw a white figure advance--a dim white figure, which held something
8605𓀴dark at its breast. The figure stopped, and at the moment a ray of
8606𓀴moonlight fell upon the masses of driving clouds and showed in startling
8607𓀴prominence a dark-haired woman, dressed in the cerements of the grave.
8608𓀴We could not see the face, for it was bent down over what we saw to be a
8609𓀴fair-haired child. There was a pause and a sharp little cry, such as a
8610𓀴child gives in sleep, or a dog as it lies before the fire and dreams. We
8611𓀴were starting forward, but the Professor’s warning hand, seen by us as
8612𓀴he stood behind a yew-tree, kept us back; and then as we looked the
8613𓀴white figure moved forwards again. It was now near enough for us to see
8614𓀴clearly, and the moonlight still held. My own heart grew cold as ice,
8615𓀴and I could hear the gasp of Arthur, as we recognised the features of
8616𓀴Lucy Westenra. Lucy Westenra, but yet how changed. The sweetness was
8617𓀴turned to adamantine, heartless cruelty, and the purity to voluptuous
8618𓀴wantonness. Van Helsing stepped out, and, obedient to his gesture, we
8619𓀴all advanced too; the four of us ranged in a line before the door of the
8620𓀴tomb. Van Helsing raised his lantern and drew the slide; by the
8621𓀴concentrated light that fell on Lucy’s face we could see that the lips
8622𓀴were crimson with fresh blood, and that the stream had trickled over her
8623𓀴chin and stained the purity of her lawn death-robe.
8624𓀴
8625𓀴We shuddered with horror. I could see by the tremulous light that even
8626𓀴Van Helsing’s iron nerve had failed. Arthur was next to me, and if I had
8627𓀴not seized his arm and held him up, he would have fallen.
8628𓀴
8629𓀴When Lucy--I call the thing that was before us Lucy because it bore her
8630𓀴shape--saw us she drew back with an angry snarl, such as a cat gives
8631𓀴when taken unawares; then her eyes ranged over us. Lucy’s eyes in form
8632𓀴and colour; but Lucy’s eyes unclean and full of hell-fire, instead of
8633𓀴the pure, gentle orbs we knew. At that moment the remnant of my love
8634𓀴passed into hate and loathing; had she then to be killed, I could have
8635𓀴done it with savage delight. As she looked, her eyes blazed with unholy
8636𓀴light, and the face became wreathed with a voluptuous smile. Oh, God,
8637𓀴how it made me shudder to see it! With a careless motion, she flung to
8638𓀴the ground, callous as a devil, the child that up to now she had
8639𓀴clutched strenuously to her breast, growling over it as a dog growls
8640𓀴over a bone. The child gave a sharp cry, and lay there moaning. There
8641𓀴was a cold-bloodedness in the act which wrung a groan from Arthur; when
8642𓀴she advanced to him with outstretched arms and a wanton smile he fell
8643𓀴back and hid his face in his hands.
8644𓀴
8645𓀴She still advanced, however, and with a languorous, voluptuous grace,
8646𓀴said:--
8647𓀴
8648𓀴“Come to me, Arthur. Leave these others and come to me. My arms are
8649𓀴hungry for you. Come, and we can rest together. Come, my husband, come!”
8650𓀴
8651𓀴There was something diabolically sweet in her tones--something of the
8652𓀴tingling of glass when struck--which rang through the brains even of us
8653𓀴who heard the words addressed to another. As for Arthur, he seemed under
8654𓀴a spell; moving his hands from his face, he opened wide his arms. She
8655𓀴was leaping for them, when Van Helsing sprang forward and held between
8656𓀴them his little golden crucifix. She recoiled from it, and, with a
8657𓀴suddenly distorted face, full of rage, dashed past him as if to enter
8658𓀴the tomb.
8659𓀴
8660𓀴When within a foot or two of the door, however, she stopped, as if
8661𓀴arrested by some irresistible force. Then she turned, and her face was
8662𓀴shown in the clear burst of moonlight and by the lamp, which had now no
8663𓀴quiver from Van Helsing’s iron nerves. Never did I see such baffled
8664𓀴malice on a face; and never, I trust, shall such ever be seen again by
8665𓀴mortal eyes. The beautiful colour became livid, the eyes seemed to throw
8666𓀴out sparks of hell-fire, the brows were wrinkled as though the folds of
8667𓀴the flesh were the coils of Medusa’s snakes, and the lovely,
8668𓀴blood-stained mouth grew to an open square, as in the passion masks of
8669𓀴the Greeks and Japanese. If ever a face meant death--if looks could
8670𓀴kill--we saw it at that moment.
8671𓀴
8672𓀴And so for full half a minute, which seemed an eternity, she remained
8673𓀴between the lifted crucifix and the sacred closing of her means of
8674𓀴entry. Van Helsing broke the silence by asking Arthur:--
8675𓀴
8676𓀴“Answer me, oh my friend! Am I to proceed in my work?”
8677𓀴
8678𓀴Arthur threw himself on his knees, and hid his face in his hands, as he
8679𓀴answered:--
8680𓀴
8681𓀴“Do as you will, friend; do as you will. There can be no horror like
8682𓀴this ever any more;” and he groaned in spirit. Quincey and I
8683𓀴simultaneously moved towards him, and took his arms. We could hear the
8684𓀴click of the closing lantern as Van Helsing held it down; coming close
8685𓀴to the tomb, he began to remove from the chinks some of the sacred
8686𓀴emblem which he had placed there. We all looked on in horrified
8687𓀴amazement as we saw, when he stood back, the woman, with a corporeal
8688𓀴body as real at that moment as our own, pass in through the interstice
8689𓀴where scarce a knife-blade could have gone. We all felt a glad sense of
8690𓀴relief when we saw the Professor calmly restoring the strings of putty
8691𓀴to the edges of the door.
8692𓀴
8693𓀴When this was done, he lifted the child and said:
8694𓀴
8695𓀴“Come now, my friends; we can do no more till to-morrow. There is a
8696𓀴funeral at noon, so here we shall all come before long after that. The
8697𓀴friends of the dead will all be gone by two, and when the sexton lock
8698𓀴the gate we shall remain. Then there is more to do; but not like this of
8699𓀴to-night. As for this little one, he is not much harm, and by to-morrow
8700𓀴night he shall be well. We shall leave him where the police will find
8701𓀴him, as on the other night; and then to home.” Coming close to Arthur,
8702𓀴he said:--
8703𓀴
8704𓀴“My friend Arthur, you have had a sore trial; but after, when you look
8705𓀴back, you will see how it was necessary. You are now in the bitter
8706𓀴waters, my child. By this time to-morrow you will, please God, have
8707𓀴passed them, and have drunk of the sweet waters; so do not mourn
8708𓀴overmuch. Till then I shall not ask you to forgive me.”
8709𓀴
8710𓀴Arthur and Quincey came home with me, and we tried to cheer each other
8711𓀴on the way. We had left the child in safety, and were tired; so we all
8712𓀴slept with more or less reality of sleep.
8713𓀴
8714𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
8715𓀴
8716𓀴_29 September, night._--A little before twelve o’clock we three--Arthur,
8717𓀴Quincey Morris, and myself--called for the Professor. It was odd to
8718𓀴notice that by common consent we had all put on black clothes. Of
8719𓀴course, Arthur wore black, for he was in deep mourning, but the rest of
8720𓀴us wore it by instinct. We got to the churchyard by half-past one, and
8721𓀴strolled about, keeping out of official observation, so that when the
8722𓀴gravediggers had completed their task and the sexton under the belief
8723𓀴that every one had gone, had locked the gate, we had the place all to
8724𓀴ourselves. Van Helsing, instead of his little black bag, had with him a
8725𓀴long leather one, something like a cricketing bag; it was manifestly of
8726𓀴fair weight.
8727𓀴
8728𓀴When we were alone and had heard the last of the footsteps die out up
8729𓀴the road, we silently, and as if by ordered intention, followed the
8730𓀴Professor to the tomb. He unlocked the door, and we entered, closing it
8731𓀴behind us. Then he took from his bag the lantern, which he lit, and also
8732𓀴two wax candles, which, when lighted, he stuck, by melting their own
8733𓀴ends, on other coffins, so that they might give light sufficient to work
8734𓀴by. When he again lifted the lid off Lucy’s coffin we all looked--Arthur
8735𓀴trembling like an aspen--and saw that the body lay there in all its
8736𓀴death-beauty. But there was no love in my own heart, nothing but
8737𓀴loathing for the foul Thing which had taken Lucy’s shape without her
8738𓀴soul. I could see even Arthur’s face grow hard as he looked. Presently
8739𓀴he said to Van Helsing:--
8740𓀴
8741𓀴“Is this really Lucy’s body, or only a demon in her shape?”
8742𓀴
8743𓀴“It is her body, and yet not it. But wait a while, and you all see her
8744𓀴as she was, and is.”
8745𓀴
8746𓀴She seemed like a nightmare of Lucy as she lay there; the pointed teeth,
8747𓀴the bloodstained, voluptuous mouth--which it made one shudder to
8748𓀴see--the whole carnal and unspiritual appearance, seeming like a
8749𓀴devilish mockery of Lucy’s sweet purity. Van Helsing, with his usual
8750𓀴methodicalness, began taking the various contents from his bag and
8751𓀴placing them ready for use. First he took out a soldering iron and some
8752𓀴plumbing solder, and then a small oil-lamp, which gave out, when lit in
8753𓀴a corner of the tomb, gas which burned at fierce heat with a blue
8754𓀴flame; then his operating knives, which he placed to hand; and last a
8755𓀴round wooden stake, some two and a half or three inches thick and about
8756𓀴three feet long. One end of it was hardened by charring in the fire, and
8757𓀴was sharpened to a fine point. With this stake came a heavy hammer, such
8758𓀴as in households is used in the coal-cellar for breaking the lumps. To
8759𓀴me, a doctor’s preparations for work of any kind are stimulating and
8760𓀴bracing, but the effect of these things on both Arthur and Quincey was
8761𓀴to cause them a sort of consternation. They both, however, kept their
8762𓀴courage, and remained silent and quiet.
8763𓀴
8764𓀴When all was ready, Van Helsing said:--
8765𓀴
8766𓀴“Before we do anything, let me tell you this; it is out of the lore and
8767𓀴experience of the ancients and of all those who have studied the powers
8768𓀴of the Un-Dead. When they become such, there comes with the change the
8769𓀴curse of immortality; they cannot die, but must go on age after age
8770𓀴adding new victims and multiplying the evils of the world; for all that
8771𓀴die from the preying of the Un-Dead becomes themselves Un-Dead, and prey
8772𓀴on their kind. And so the circle goes on ever widening, like as the
8773𓀴ripples from a stone thrown in the water. Friend Arthur, if you had met
8774𓀴that kiss which you know of before poor Lucy die; or again, last night
8775𓀴when you open your arms to her, you would in time, when you had died,
8776𓀴have become _nosferatu_, as they call it in Eastern Europe, and would
8777𓀴all time make more of those Un-Deads that so have fill us with horror.
8778𓀴The career of this so unhappy dear lady is but just begun. Those
8779𓀴children whose blood she suck are not as yet so much the worse; but if
8780𓀴she live on, Un-Dead, more and more they lose their blood and by her
8781𓀴power over them they come to her; and so she draw their blood with that
8782𓀴so wicked mouth. But if she die in truth, then all cease; the tiny
8783𓀴wounds of the throats disappear, and they go back to their plays
8784𓀴unknowing ever of what has been. But of the most blessed of all, when
8785𓀴this now Un-Dead be made to rest as true dead, then the soul of the poor
8786𓀴lady whom we love shall again be free. Instead of working wickedness by
8787𓀴night and growing more debased in the assimilating of it by day, she
8788𓀴shall take her place with the other Angels. So that, my friend, it will
8789𓀴be a blessed hand for her that shall strike the blow that sets her free.
8790𓀴To this I am willing; but is there none amongst us who has a better
8791𓀴right? Will it be no joy to think of hereafter in the silence of the
8792𓀴night when sleep is not: ‘It was my hand that sent her to the stars; it
8793𓀴was the hand of him that loved her best; the hand that of all she would
8794𓀴herself have chosen, had it been to her to choose?’ Tell me if there be
8795𓀴such a one amongst us?”
8796𓀴
8797𓀴We all looked at Arthur. He saw, too, what we all did, the infinite
8798𓀴kindness which suggested that his should be the hand which would restore
8799𓀴Lucy to us as a holy, and not an unholy, memory; he stepped forward and
8800𓀴said bravely, though his hand trembled, and his face was as pale as
8801𓀴snow:--
8802𓀴
8803𓀴“My true friend, from the bottom of my broken heart I thank you. Tell me
8804𓀴what I am to do, and I shall not falter!” Van Helsing laid a hand on his
8805𓀴shoulder, and said:--
8806𓀴
8807𓀴“Brave lad! A moment’s courage, and it is done. This stake must be
8808𓀴driven through her. It will be a fearful ordeal--be not deceived in
8809𓀴that--but it will be only a short time, and you will then rejoice more
8810𓀴than your pain was great; from this grim tomb you will emerge as though
8811𓀴you tread on air. But you must not falter when once you have begun. Only
8812𓀴think that we, your true friends, are round you, and that we pray for
8813𓀴you all the time.”
8814𓀴
8815𓀴“Go on,” said Arthur hoarsely. “Tell me what I am to do.”
8816𓀴
8817𓀴“Take this stake in your left hand, ready to place the point over the
8818𓀴heart, and the hammer in your right. Then when we begin our prayer for
8819𓀴the dead--I shall read him, I have here the book, and the others shall
8820𓀴follow--strike in God’s name, that so all may be well with the dead that
8821𓀴we love and that the Un-Dead pass away.”
8822𓀴
8823𓀴Arthur took the stake and the hammer, and when once his mind was set on
8824𓀴action his hands never trembled nor even quivered. Van Helsing opened
8825𓀴his missal and began to read, and Quincey and I followed as well as we
8826𓀴could. Arthur placed the point over the heart, and as I looked I could
8827𓀴see its dint in the white flesh. Then he struck with all his might.
8828𓀴
8829𓀴The Thing in the coffin writhed; and a hideous, blood-curdling screech
8830𓀴came from the opened red lips. The body shook and quivered and twisted
8831𓀴in wild contortions; the sharp white teeth champed together till the
8832𓀴lips were cut, and the mouth was smeared with a crimson foam. But Arthur
8833𓀴never faltered. He looked like a figure of Thor as his untrembling arm
8834𓀴rose and fell, driving deeper and deeper the mercy-bearing stake, whilst
8835𓀴the blood from the pierced heart welled and spurted up around it. His
8836𓀴face was set, and high duty seemed to shine through it; the sight of it
8837𓀴gave us courage so that our voices seemed to ring through the little
8838𓀴vault.
8839𓀴
8840𓀴And then the writhing and quivering of the body became less, and the
8841𓀴teeth seemed to champ, and the face to quiver. Finally it lay still. The
8842𓀴terrible task was over.
8843𓀴
8844𓀴The hammer fell from Arthur’s hand. He reeled and would have fallen had
8845𓀴we not caught him. The great drops of sweat sprang from his forehead,
8846𓀴and his breath came in broken gasps. It had indeed been an awful strain
8847𓀴on him; and had he not been forced to his task by more than human
8848𓀴considerations he could never have gone through with it. For a few
8849𓀴minutes we were so taken up with him that we did not look towards the
8850𓀴coffin. When we did, however, a murmur of startled surprise ran from one
8851𓀴to the other of us. We gazed so eagerly that Arthur rose, for he had
8852𓀴been seated on the ground, and came and looked too; and then a glad,
8853𓀴strange light broke over his face and dispelled altogether the gloom of
8854𓀴horror that lay upon it.
8855𓀴
8856𓀴There, in the coffin lay no longer the foul Thing that we had so dreaded
8857𓀴and grown to hate that the work of her destruction was yielded as a
8858𓀴privilege to the one best entitled to it, but Lucy as we had seen her in
8859𓀴her life, with her face of unequalled sweetness and purity. True that
8860𓀴there were there, as we had seen them in life, the traces of care and
8861𓀴pain and waste; but these were all dear to us, for they marked her truth
8862𓀴to what we knew. One and all we felt that the holy calm that lay like
8863𓀴sunshine over the wasted face and form was only an earthly token and
8864𓀴symbol of the calm that was to reign for ever.
8865𓀴
8866𓀴Van Helsing came and laid his hand on Arthur’s shoulder, and said to
8867𓀴him:--
8868𓀴
8869𓀴“And now, Arthur my friend, dear lad, am I not forgiven?”
8870𓀴
8871𓀴The reaction of the terrible strain came as he took the old man’s hand
8872𓀴in his, and raising it to his lips, pressed it, and said:--
8873𓀴
8874𓀴“Forgiven! God bless you that you have given my dear one her soul again,
8875𓀴and me peace.” He put his hands on the Professor’s shoulder, and laying
8876𓀴his head on his breast, cried for a while silently, whilst we stood
8877𓀴unmoving. When he raised his head Van Helsing said to him:--
8878𓀴
8879𓀴“And now, my child, you may kiss her. Kiss her dead lips if you will, as
8880𓀴she would have you to, if for her to choose. For she is not a grinning
8881𓀴devil now--not any more a foul Thing for all eternity. No longer she is
8882𓀴the devil’s Un-Dead. She is God’s true dead, whose soul is with Him!”
8883𓀴
8884𓀴Arthur bent and kissed her, and then we sent him and Quincey out of the
8885𓀴tomb; the Professor and I sawed the top off the stake, leaving the point
8886𓀴of it in the body. Then we cut off the head and filled the mouth with
8887𓀴garlic. We soldered up the leaden coffin, screwed on the coffin-lid,
8888𓀴and gathering up our belongings, came away. When the Professor locked
8889𓀴the door he gave the key to Arthur.
8890𓀴
8891𓀴Outside the air was sweet, the sun shone, and the birds sang, and it
8892𓀴seemed as if all nature were tuned to a different pitch. There was
8893𓀴gladness and mirth and peace everywhere, for we were at rest ourselves
8894𓀴on one account, and we were glad, though it was with a tempered joy.
8895𓀴
8896𓀴Before we moved away Van Helsing said:--
8897𓀴
8898𓀴“Now, my friends, one step of our work is done, one the most harrowing
8899𓀴to ourselves. But there remains a greater task: to find out the author
8900𓀴of all this our sorrow and to stamp him out. I have clues which we can
8901𓀴follow; but it is a long task, and a difficult, and there is danger in
8902𓀴it, and pain. Shall you not all help me? We have learned to believe, all
8903𓀴of us--is it not so? And since so, do we not see our duty? Yes! And do
8904𓀴we not promise to go on to the bitter end?”
8905𓀴
8906𓀴Each in turn, we took his hand, and the promise was made. Then said the
8907𓀴Professor as we moved off:--
8908𓀴
8909𓀴“Two nights hence you shall meet with me and dine together at seven of
8910𓀴the clock with friend John. I shall entreat two others, two that you
8911𓀴know not as yet; and I shall be ready to all our work show and our plans
8912𓀴unfold. Friend John, you come with me home, for I have much to consult
8913𓀴about, and you can help me. To-night I leave for Amsterdam, but shall
8914𓀴return to-morrow night. And then begins our great quest. But first I
8915𓀴shall have much to say, so that you may know what is to do and to dread.
8916𓀴Then our promise shall be made to each other anew; for there is a
8917𓀴terrible task before us, and once our feet are on the ploughshare we
8918𓀴must not draw back.”
8919𓀴
8920𓀴
8921𓀴
8922𓀴
8923𓀴CHAPTER XVII
8924𓀴
8925𓀴DR. SEWARD’S DIARY--_continued_
8926𓀴
8927𓀴
8928𓀴When we arrived at the Berkeley Hotel, Van Helsing found a telegram
8929𓀴waiting for him:--
8930𓀴
8931𓀴     “Am coming up by train. Jonathan at Whitby. Important news.--MINA
8932𓀴     HARKER.”
8933𓀴
8934𓀴The Professor was delighted. “Ah, that wonderful Madam Mina,” he said,
8935𓀴“pearl among women! She arrive, but I cannot stay. She must go to your
8936𓀴house, friend John. You must meet her at the station. Telegraph her _en
8937𓀴route_, so that she may be prepared.”
8938𓀴
8939𓀴When the wire was despatched he had a cup of tea; over it he told me of
8940𓀴a diary kept by Jonathan Harker when abroad, and gave me a typewritten
8941𓀴copy of it, as also of Mrs. Harker’s diary at Whitby. “Take these,” he
8942𓀴said, “and study them well. When I have returned you will be master of
8943𓀴all the facts, and we can then better enter on our inquisition. Keep
8944𓀴them safe, for there is in them much of treasure. You will need all your
8945𓀴faith, even you who have had such an experience as that of to-day. What
8946𓀴is here told,” he laid his hand heavily and gravely on the packet of
8947𓀴papers as he spoke, “may be the beginning of the end to you and me and
8948𓀴many another; or it may sound the knell of the Un-Dead who walk the
8949𓀴earth. Read all, I pray you, with the open mind; and if you can add in
8950𓀴any way to the story here told do so, for it is all-important. You have
8951𓀴kept diary of all these so strange things; is it not so? Yes! Then we
8952𓀴shall go through all these together when we meet.” He then made ready
8953𓀴for his departure, and shortly after drove off to Liverpool Street. I
8954𓀴took my way to Paddington, where I arrived about fifteen minutes before
8955𓀴the train came in.
8956𓀴
8957𓀴The crowd melted away, after the bustling fashion common to arrival
8958𓀴platforms; and I was beginning to feel uneasy, lest I might miss my
8959𓀴guest, when a sweet-faced, dainty-looking girl stepped up to me, and,
8960𓀴after a quick glance, said: “Dr. Seward, is it not?”
8961𓀴
8962𓀴“And you are Mrs. Harker!” I answered at once; whereupon she held out
8963𓀴her hand.
8964𓀴
8965𓀴“I knew you from the description of poor dear Lucy; but----” She stopped
8966𓀴suddenly, and a quick blush overspread her face.
8967𓀴
8968𓀴The blush that rose to my own cheeks somehow set us both at ease, for it
8969𓀴was a tacit answer to her own. I got her luggage, which included a
8970𓀴typewriter, and we took the Underground to Fenchurch Street, after I had
8971𓀴sent a wire to my housekeeper to have a sitting-room and bedroom
8972𓀴prepared at once for Mrs. Harker.
8973𓀴
8974𓀴In due time we arrived. She knew, of course, that the place was a
8975𓀴lunatic asylum, but I could see that she was unable to repress a shudder
8976𓀴when we entered.
8977𓀴
8978𓀴She told me that, if she might, she would come presently to my study, as
8979𓀴she had much to say. So here I am finishing my entry in my phonograph
8980𓀴diary whilst I await her. As yet I have not had the chance of looking at
8981𓀴the papers which Van Helsing left with me, though they lie open before
8982𓀴me. I must get her interested in something, so that I may have an
8983𓀴opportunity of reading them. She does not know how precious time is, or
8984𓀴what a task we have in hand. I must be careful not to frighten her. Here
8985𓀴she is!
8986𓀴
8987𓀴
8988𓀴_Mina Harker’s Journal._
8989𓀴
8990𓀴_29 September._--After I had tidied myself, I went down to Dr. Seward’s
8991𓀴study. At the door I paused a moment, for I thought I heard him talking
8992𓀴with some one. As, however, he had pressed me to be quick, I knocked at
8993𓀴the door, and on his calling out, “Come in,” I entered.
8994𓀴
8995𓀴To my intense surprise, there was no one with him. He was quite alone,
8996𓀴and on the table opposite him was what I knew at once from the
8997𓀴description to be a phonograph. I had never seen one, and was much
8998𓀴interested.
8999𓀴
9000𓀴“I hope I did not keep you waiting,” I said; “but I stayed at the door
9001𓀴as I heard you talking, and thought there was some one with you.”
9002𓀴
9003𓀴“Oh,” he replied with a smile, “I was only entering my diary.”
9004𓀴
9005𓀴“Your diary?” I asked him in surprise.
9006𓀴
9007𓀴“Yes,” he answered. “I keep it in this.” As he spoke he laid his hand on
9008𓀴the phonograph. I felt quite excited over it, and blurted out:--
9009𓀴
9010𓀴“Why, this beats even shorthand! May I hear it say something?”
9011𓀴
9012𓀴“Certainly,” he replied with alacrity, and stood up to put it in train
9013𓀴for speaking. Then he paused, and a troubled look overspread his face.
9014𓀴
9015𓀴“The fact is,” he began awkwardly, “I only keep my diary in it; and as
9016𓀴it is entirely--almost entirely--about my cases, it may be awkward--that
9017𓀴is, I mean----” He stopped, and I tried to help him out of his
9018𓀴embarrassment:--
9019𓀴
9020𓀴“You helped to attend dear Lucy at the end. Let me hear how she died;
9021𓀴for all that I know of her, I shall be very grateful. She was very, very
9022𓀴dear to me.”
9023𓀴
9024𓀴To my surprise, he answered, with a horrorstruck look in his face:--
9025𓀴
9026𓀴“Tell you of her death? Not for the wide world!”
9027𓀴
9028𓀴“Why not?” I asked, for some grave, terrible feeling was coming over me.
9029𓀴Again he paused, and I could see that he was trying to invent an excuse.
9030𓀴At length he stammered out:--
9031𓀴
9032𓀴“You see, I do not know how to pick out any particular part of the
9033𓀴diary.” Even while he was speaking an idea dawned upon him, and he said
9034𓀴with unconscious simplicity, in a different voice, and with the naïveté
9035𓀴of a child: “That’s quite true, upon my honour. Honest Indian!” I could
9036𓀴not but smile, at which he grimaced. “I gave myself away that time!” he
9037𓀴said. “But do you know that, although I have kept the diary for months
9038𓀴past, it never once struck me how I was going to find any particular
9039𓀴part of it in case I wanted to look it up?” By this time my mind was
9040𓀴made up that the diary of a doctor who attended Lucy might have
9041𓀴something to add to the sum of our knowledge of that terrible Being, and
9042𓀴I said boldly:--
9043𓀴
9044𓀴“Then, Dr. Seward, you had better let me copy it out for you on my
9045𓀴typewriter.” He grew to a positively deathly pallor as he said:--
9046𓀴
9047𓀴“No! no! no! For all the world, I wouldn’t let you know that terrible
9048𓀴story!”
9049𓀴
9050𓀴Then it was terrible; my intuition was right! For a moment I thought,
9051𓀴and as my eyes ranged the room, unconsciously looking for something or
9052𓀴some opportunity to aid me, they lit on a great batch of typewriting on
9053𓀴the table. His eyes caught the look in mine, and, without his thinking,
9054𓀴followed their direction. As they saw the parcel he realised my meaning.
9055𓀴
9056𓀴“You do not know me,” I said. “When you have read those papers--my own
9057𓀴diary and my husband’s also, which I have typed--you will know me
9058𓀴better. I have not faltered in giving every thought of my own heart in
9059𓀴this cause; but, of course, you do not know me--yet; and I must not
9060𓀴expect you to trust me so far.”
9061𓀴
9062𓀴He is certainly a man of noble nature; poor dear Lucy was right about
9063𓀴him. He stood up and opened a large drawer, in which were arranged in
9064𓀴order a number of hollow cylinders of metal covered with dark wax, and
9065𓀴said:--
9066𓀴
9067𓀴“You are quite right. I did not trust you because I did not know you.
9068𓀴But I know you now; and let me say that I should have known you long
9069𓀴ago. I know that Lucy told you of me; she told me of you too. May I make
9070𓀴the only atonement in my power? Take the cylinders and hear them--the
9071𓀴first half-dozen of them are personal to me, and they will not horrify
9072𓀴you; then you will know me better. Dinner will by then be ready. In the
9073𓀴meantime I shall read over some of these documents, and shall be better
9074𓀴able to understand certain things.” He carried the phonograph himself up
9075𓀴to my sitting-room and adjusted it for me. Now I shall learn something
9076𓀴pleasant, I am sure; for it will tell me the other side of a true love
9077𓀴episode of which I know one side already....
9078𓀴
9079𓀴
9080𓀴_Dr. Seward’s Diary._
9081𓀴
9082𓀴_29 September._--I was so absorbed in that wonderful diary of Jonathan
9083𓀴Harker and that other of his wife that I let the time run on without
9084𓀴thinking. Mrs. Harker was not down when the maid came to announce
9085𓀴dinner, so I said: “She is possibly tired; let dinner wait an hour,” and
9086𓀴I went on with my work. I had just finished Mrs. Harker’s diary, when
9087𓀴she came in. She looked sweetly pretty, but very sad, and her eyes were
9088𓀴flushed with crying. This somehow moved me much. Of late I have had
9089𓀴cause for tears, God knows! but the relief of them was denied me; and
9090𓀴now the sight of those sweet eyes, brightened with recent tears, went
9091𓀴straight to my heart. So I said as gently as I could:--
9092𓀴
9093𓀴“I greatly fear I have distressed you.”
9094𓀴
9095𓀴“Oh, no, not distressed me,” she replied, “but I have been more touched
9096𓀴than I can say by your grief. That is a wonderful machine, but it is
9097𓀴cruelly true. It told me, in its very tones, the anguish of your heart.
9098𓀴It was like a soul crying out to Almighty God. No one must hear them
9099𓀴spoken ever again! See, I have tried to be useful. I have copied out the
9100𓀴words on my typewriter, and none other need now hear your heart beat, as
9101𓀴I did.”
9102𓀴
9103𓀴“No one need ever know, shall ever know,” I said in a low voice. She
9104𓀴laid her hand on mine and said very gravely:--
9105𓀴
9106𓀴“Ah, but they must!”
9107𓀴
9108𓀴“Must! But why?” I asked.
9109𓀴
9110𓀴“Because it is a part of the terrible story, a part of poor dear Lucy’s
9111𓀴death and all that led to it; because in the struggle which we have
9112𓀴before us to rid the earth of this terrible monster we must have all
9113𓀴the knowledge and all the help which we can get. I think that the
9114𓀴cylinders which you gave me contained more than you intended me to know;
9115𓀴but I can see that there are in your record many lights to this dark
9116𓀴mystery. You will let me help, will you not? I know all up to a certain
9117𓀴point; and I see already, though your diary only took me to 7 September,
9118𓀴how poor Lucy was beset, and how her terrible doom was being wrought
9119𓀴out. Jonathan and I have been working day and night since Professor Van
9120𓀴Helsing saw us. He is gone to Whitby to get more information, and he
9121𓀴will be here to-morrow to help us. We need have no secrets amongst us;
9122𓀴working together and with absolute trust, we can surely be stronger than
9123𓀴if some of us were in the dark.” She looked at me so appealingly, and at
9124𓀴the same time manifested such courage and resolution in her bearing,
9125𓀴that I gave in at once to her wishes. “You shall,” I said, “do as you
9126𓀴like in the matter. God forgive me if I do wrong! There are terrible
9127𓀴things yet to learn of; but if you have so far travelled on the road to
9128𓀴poor Lucy’s death, you will not be content, I know, to remain in the
9129𓀴dark. Nay, the end--the very end--may give you a gleam of peace. Come,
9130𓀴there is dinner. We must keep one another strong for what is before us;
9131𓀴we have a cruel and dreadful task. When you have eaten you shall learn
9132𓀴the rest, and I shall answer any questions you ask--if there be anything
9133𓀴which you do not understand, though it was apparent to us who were
9134𓀴present.”
9135𓀴
9136𓀴
9137𓀴_Mina Harker’s Journal._
9138𓀴
9139𓀴_29 September._--After dinner I came with Dr. Seward to his study. He
9140𓀴brought back the phonograph from my room, and I took my typewriter. He
9141𓀴placed me in a comfortable chair, and arranged the phonograph so that I
9142𓀴could touch it without getting up, and showed me how to stop it in case
9143𓀴I should want to pause. Then he very thoughtfully took a chair, with his
9144𓀴back to me, so that I might be as free as possible, and began to read. I
9145𓀴put the forked metal to my ears and listened.
9146𓀴
9147𓀴When the terrible story of Lucy’s death, and--and all that followed, was
9148𓀴done, I lay back in my chair powerless. Fortunately I am not of a
9149𓀴fainting disposition. When Dr. Seward saw me he jumped up with a
9150𓀴horrified exclamation, and hurriedly taking a case-bottle from a
9151𓀴cupboard, gave me some brandy, which in a few minutes somewhat restored
9152𓀴me. My brain was all in a whirl, and only that there came through all
9153𓀴the multitude of horrors, the holy ray of light that my dear, dear Lucy
9154𓀴was at last at peace, I do not think I could have borne it without
9155𓀴making a scene. It is all so wild, and mysterious, and strange that if I
9156𓀴had not known Jonathan’s experience in Transylvania I could not have
9157𓀴believed. As it was, I didn’t know what to believe, and so got out of my
9158𓀴difficulty by attending to something else. I took the cover off my
9159𓀴typewriter, and said to Dr. Seward:--
9160𓀴
9161𓀴“Let me write this all out now. We must be ready for Dr. Van Helsing
9162𓀴when he comes. I have sent a telegram to Jonathan to come on here when
9163𓀴he arrives in London from Whitby. In this matter dates are everything,
9164𓀴and I think that if we get all our material ready, and have every item
9165𓀴put in chronological order, we shall have done much. You tell me that
9166𓀴Lord Godalming and Mr. Morris are coming too. Let us be able to tell him
9167𓀴when they come.” He accordingly set the phonograph at a slow pace, and I
9168𓀴began to typewrite from the beginning of the seventh cylinder. I used
9169𓀴manifold, and so took three copies of the diary, just as I had done with
9170𓀴all the rest. It was late when I got through, but Dr. Seward went about
9171𓀴his work of going his round of the patients; when he had finished he
9172𓀴came back and sat near me, reading, so that I did not feel too lonely
9173𓀴whilst I worked. How good and thoughtful he is; the world seems full of
9174𓀴good men--even if there _are_ monsters in it. Before I left him I
9175𓀴remembered what Jonathan put in his diary of the Professor’s
9176𓀴perturbation at reading something in an evening paper at the station at
9177𓀴Exeter; so, seeing that Dr. Seward keeps his newspapers, I borrowed the
9178𓀴files of “The Westminster Gazette” and “The Pall Mall Gazette,” and took
9179𓀴them to my room. I remember how much “The Dailygraph” and “The Whitby
9180𓀴Gazette,” of which I had made cuttings, helped us to understand the
9181𓀴terrible events at Whitby when Count Dracula landed, so I shall look
9182𓀴through the evening papers since then, and perhaps I shall get some new
9183𓀴light. I am not sleepy, and the work will help to keep me quiet.
9184𓀴
9185𓀴
9186𓀴_Dr. Seward’s Diary._
9187𓀴
9188𓀴_30 September._--Mr. Harker arrived at nine o’clock. He had got his
9189𓀴wife’s wire just before starting. He is uncommonly clever, if one can
9190𓀴judge from his face, and full of energy. If this journal be true--and
9191𓀴judging by one’s own wonderful experiences, it must be--he is also a man
9192𓀴of great nerve. That going down to the vault a second time was a
9193𓀴remarkable piece of daring. After reading his account of it I was
9194𓀴prepared to meet a good specimen of manhood, but hardly the quiet,
9195𓀴business-like gentleman who came here to-day.
9196𓀴
9197𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
9198𓀴
9199𓀴_Later._--After lunch Harker and his wife went back to their own room,
9200𓀴and as I passed a while ago I heard the click of the typewriter. They
9201𓀴are hard at it. Mrs. Harker says that they are knitting together in
9202𓀴chronological order every scrap of evidence they have. Harker has got
9203𓀴the letters between the consignee of the boxes at Whitby and the
9204𓀴carriers in London who took charge of them. He is now reading his wife’s
9205𓀴typescript of my diary. I wonder what they make out of it. Here it
9206𓀴is....
9207𓀴
9208𓀴     Strange that it never struck me that the very next house might be
9209𓀴     the Count’s hiding-place! Goodness knows that we had enough clues
9210𓀴     from the conduct of the patient Renfield! The bundle of letters
9211𓀴     relating to the purchase of the house were with the typescript. Oh,
9212𓀴     if we had only had them earlier we might have saved poor Lucy!
9213𓀴     Stop; that way madness lies! Harker has gone back, and is again
9214𓀴     collating his material. He says that by dinner-time they will be
9215𓀴     able to show a whole connected narrative. He thinks that in the
9216𓀴     meantime I should see Renfield, as hitherto he has been a sort of
9217𓀴     index to the coming and going of the Count. I hardly see this yet,
9218𓀴     but when I get at the dates I suppose I shall. What a good thing
9219𓀴     that Mrs. Harker put my cylinders into type! We never could have
9220𓀴     found the dates otherwise....
9221𓀴
9222𓀴     I found Renfield sitting placidly in his room with his hands
9223𓀴     folded, smiling benignly. At the moment he seemed as sane as any
9224𓀴     one I ever saw. I sat down and talked with him on a lot of
9225𓀴     subjects, all of which he treated naturally. He then, of his own
9226𓀴     accord, spoke of going home, a subject he has never mentioned to my
9227𓀴     knowledge during his sojourn here. In fact, he spoke quite
9228𓀴     confidently of getting his discharge at once. I believe that, had I
9229𓀴     not had the chat with Harker and read the letters and the dates of
9230𓀴     his outbursts, I should have been prepared to sign for him after a
9231𓀴     brief time of observation. As it is, I am darkly suspicious. All
9232𓀴     those outbreaks were in some way linked with the proximity of the
9233𓀴     Count. What then does this absolute content mean? Can it be that
9234𓀴     his instinct is satisfied as to the vampire’s ultimate triumph?
9235𓀴     Stay; he is himself zoöphagous, and in his wild ravings outside the
9236𓀴     chapel door of the deserted house he always spoke of “master.” This
9237𓀴     all seems confirmation of our idea. However, after a while I came
9238𓀴     away; my friend is just a little too sane at present to make it
9239𓀴     safe to probe him too deep with questions. He might begin to think,
9240𓀴     and then--! So I came away. I mistrust these quiet moods of his; so
9241𓀴     I have given the attendant a hint to look closely after him, and to
9242𓀴     have a strait-waistcoat ready in case of need.
9243𓀴
9244𓀴
9245𓀴_Jonathan Harker’s Journal._
9246𓀴
9247𓀴_29 September, in train to London._--When I received Mr. Billington’s
9248𓀴courteous message that he would give me any information in his power I
9249𓀴thought it best to go down to Whitby and make, on the spot, such
9250𓀴inquiries as I wanted. It was now my object to trace that horrid cargo
9251𓀴of the Count’s to its place in London. Later, we may be able to deal
9252𓀴with it. Billington junior, a nice lad, met me at the station, and
9253𓀴brought me to his father’s house, where they had decided that I must
9254𓀴stay the night. They are hospitable, with true Yorkshire hospitality:
9255𓀴give a guest everything, and leave him free to do as he likes. They all
9256𓀴knew that I was busy, and that my stay was short, and Mr. Billington had
9257𓀴ready in his office all the papers concerning the consignment of boxes.
9258𓀴It gave me almost a turn to see again one of the letters which I had
9259𓀴seen on the Count’s table before I knew of his diabolical plans.
9260𓀴Everything had been carefully thought out, and done systematically and
9261𓀴with precision. He seemed to have been prepared for every obstacle which
9262𓀴might be placed by accident in the way of his intentions being carried
9263𓀴out. To use an Americanism, he had “taken no chances,” and the absolute
9264𓀴accuracy with which his instructions were fulfilled, was simply the
9265𓀴logical result of his care. I saw the invoice, and took note of it:
9266𓀴“Fifty cases of common earth, to be used for experimental purposes.”
9267𓀴Also the copy of letter to Carter Paterson, and their reply; of both of
9268𓀴these I got copies. This was all the information Mr. Billington could
9269𓀴give me, so I went down to the port and saw the coastguards, the Customs
9270𓀴officers and the harbour-master. They had all something to say of the
9271𓀴strange entry of the ship, which is already taking its place in local
9272𓀴tradition; but no one could add to the simple description “Fifty cases
9273𓀴of common earth.” I then saw the station-master, who kindly put me in
9274𓀴communication with the men who had actually received the boxes. Their
9275𓀴tally was exact with the list, and they had nothing to add except that
9276𓀴the boxes were “main and mortal heavy,” and that shifting them was dry
9277𓀴work. One of them added that it was hard lines that there wasn’t any
9278𓀴gentleman “such-like as yourself, squire,” to show some sort of
9279𓀴appreciation of their efforts in a liquid form; another put in a rider
9280𓀴that the thirst then generated was such that even the time which had
9281𓀴elapsed had not completely allayed it. Needless to add, I took care
9282𓀴before leaving to lift, for ever and adequately, this source of
9283𓀴reproach.
9284𓀴
9285𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
9286𓀴
9287𓀴_30 September._--The station-master was good enough to give me a line to
9288𓀴his old companion the station-master at King’s Cross, so that when I
9289𓀴arrived there in the morning I was able to ask him about the arrival of
9290𓀴the boxes. He, too, put me at once in communication with the proper
9291𓀴officials, and I saw that their tally was correct with the original
9292𓀴invoice. The opportunities of acquiring an abnormal thirst had been here
9293𓀴limited; a noble use of them had, however, been made, and again I was
9294𓀴compelled to deal with the result in an _ex post facto_ manner.
9295𓀴
9296𓀴From thence I went on to Carter Paterson’s central office, where I met
9297𓀴with the utmost courtesy. They looked up the transaction in their
9298𓀴day-book and letter-book, and at once telephoned to their King’s Cross
9299𓀴office for more details. By good fortune, the men who did the teaming
9300𓀴were waiting for work, and the official at once sent them over, sending
9301𓀴also by one of them the way-bill and all the papers connected with the
9302𓀴delivery of the boxes at Carfax. Here again I found the tally agreeing
9303𓀴exactly; the carriers’ men were able to supplement the paucity of the
9304𓀴written words with a few details. These were, I shortly found, connected
9305𓀴almost solely with the dusty nature of the job, and of the consequent
9306𓀴thirst engendered in the operators. On my affording an opportunity,
9307𓀴through the medium of the currency of the realm, of the allaying, at a
9308𓀴later period, this beneficial evil, one of the men remarked:--
9309𓀴
9310𓀴“That ’ere ’ouse, guv’nor, is the rummiest I ever was in. Blyme! but it
9311𓀴ain’t been touched sence a hundred years. There was dust that thick in
9312𓀴the place that you might have slep’ on it without ’urtin’ of yer bones;
9313𓀴an’ the place was that neglected that yer might ’ave smelled ole
9314𓀴Jerusalem in it. But the ole chapel--that took the cike, that did! Me
9315𓀴and my mate, we thort we wouldn’t never git out quick enough. Lor’, I
9316𓀴wouldn’t take less nor a quid a moment to stay there arter dark.”
9317𓀴
9318𓀴Having been in the house, I could well believe him; but if he knew what
9319𓀴I know, he would, I think, have raised his terms.
9320𓀴
9321𓀴Of one thing I am now satisfied: that _all_ the boxes which arrived at
9322𓀴Whitby from Varna in the _Demeter_ were safely deposited in the old
9323𓀴chapel at Carfax. There should be fifty of them there, unless any have
9324𓀴since been removed--as from Dr. Seward’s diary I fear.
9325𓀴
9326𓀴I shall try to see the carter who took away the boxes from Carfax when
9327𓀴Renfield attacked them. By following up this clue we may learn a good
9328𓀴deal.
9329𓀴
9330𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
9331𓀴
9332𓀴_Later._--Mina and I have worked all day, and we have put all the papers
9333𓀴into order.
9334𓀴
9335𓀴
9336𓀴_Mina Harker’s Journal_
9337𓀴
9338𓀴_30 September._--I am so glad that I hardly know how to contain myself.
9339𓀴It is, I suppose, the reaction from the haunting fear which I have had:
9340𓀴that this terrible affair and the reopening of his old wound might act
9341𓀴detrimentally on Jonathan. I saw him leave for Whitby with as brave a
9342𓀴face as I could, but I was sick with apprehension. The effort has,
9343𓀴however, done him good. He was never so resolute, never so strong, never
9344𓀴so full of volcanic energy, as at present. It is just as that dear, good
9345𓀴Professor Van Helsing said: he is true grit, and he improves under
9346𓀴strain that would kill a weaker nature. He came back full of life and
9347𓀴hope and determination; we have got everything in order for to-night. I
9348𓀴feel myself quite wild with excitement. I suppose one ought to pity any
9349𓀴thing so hunted as is the Count. That is just it: this Thing is not
9350𓀴human--not even beast. To read Dr. Seward’s account of poor Lucy’s
9351𓀴death, and what followed, is enough to dry up the springs of pity in
9352𓀴one’s heart.
9353𓀴
9354𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
9355𓀴
9356𓀴_Later._--Lord Godalming and Mr. Morris arrived earlier than we
9357𓀴expected. Dr. Seward was out on business, and had taken Jonathan with
9358𓀴him, so I had to see them. It was to me a painful meeting, for it
9359𓀴brought back all poor dear Lucy’s hopes of only a few months ago. Of
9360𓀴course they had heard Lucy speak of me, and it seemed that Dr. Van
9361𓀴Helsing, too, has been quite “blowing my trumpet,” as Mr. Morris
9362𓀴expressed it. Poor fellows, neither of them is aware that I know all
9363𓀴about the proposals they made to Lucy. They did not quite know what to
9364𓀴say or do, as they were ignorant of the amount of my knowledge; so they
9365𓀴had to keep on neutral subjects. However, I thought the matter over, and
9366𓀴came to the conclusion that the best thing I could do would be to post
9367𓀴them in affairs right up to date. I knew from Dr. Seward’s diary that
9368𓀴they had been at Lucy’s death--her real death--and that I need not fear
9369𓀴to betray any secret before the time. So I told them, as well as I
9370𓀴could, that I had read all the papers and diaries, and that my husband
9371𓀴and I, having typewritten them, had just finished putting them in order.
9372𓀴I gave them each a copy to read in the library. When Lord Godalming got
9373𓀴his and turned it over--it does make a pretty good pile--he said:--
9374𓀴
9375𓀴“Did you write all this, Mrs. Harker?”
9376𓀴
9377𓀴I nodded, and he went on:--
9378𓀴
9379𓀴“I don’t quite see the drift of it; but you people are all so good and
9380𓀴kind, and have been working so earnestly and so energetically, that all
9381𓀴I can do is to accept your ideas blindfold and try to help you. I have
9382𓀴had one lesson already in accepting facts that should make a man humble
9383𓀴to the last hour of his life. Besides, I know you loved my poor Lucy--”
9384𓀴Here he turned away and covered his face with his hands. I could hear
9385𓀴the tears in his voice. Mr. Morris, with instinctive delicacy, just laid
9386𓀴a hand for a moment on his shoulder, and then walked quietly out of the
9387𓀴room. I suppose there is something in woman’s nature that makes a man
9388𓀴free to break down before her and express his feelings on the tender or
9389𓀴emotional side without feeling it derogatory to his manhood; for when
9390𓀴Lord Godalming found himself alone with me he sat down on the sofa and
9391𓀴gave way utterly and openly. I sat down beside him and took his hand. I
9392𓀴hope he didn’t think it forward of me, and that if he ever thinks of it
9393𓀴afterwards he never will have such a thought. There I wrong him; I
9394𓀴_know_ he never will--he is too true a gentleman. I said to him, for I
9395𓀴could see that his heart was breaking:--
9396𓀴
9397𓀴“I loved dear Lucy, and I know what she was to you, and what you were to
9398𓀴her. She and I were like sisters; and now she is gone, will you not let
9399𓀴me be like a sister to you in your trouble? I know what sorrows you have
9400𓀴had, though I cannot measure the depth of them. If sympathy and pity can
9401𓀴help in your affliction, won’t you let me be of some little service--for
9402𓀴Lucy’s sake?”
9403𓀴
9404𓀴In an instant the poor dear fellow was overwhelmed with grief. It seemed
9405𓀴to me that all that he had of late been suffering in silence found a
9406𓀴vent at once. He grew quite hysterical, and raising his open hands, beat
9407𓀴his palms together in a perfect agony of grief. He stood up and then sat
9408𓀴down again, and the tears rained down his cheeks. I felt an infinite
9409𓀴pity for him, and opened my arms unthinkingly. With a sob he laid his
9410𓀴head on my shoulder and cried like a wearied child, whilst he shook with
9411𓀴emotion.
9412𓀴
9413𓀴We women have something of the mother in us that makes us rise above
9414𓀴smaller matters when the mother-spirit is invoked; I felt this big
9415𓀴sorrowing man’s head resting on me, as though it were that of the baby
9416𓀴that some day may lie on my bosom, and I stroked his hair as though he
9417𓀴were my own child. I never thought at the time how strange it all was.
9418𓀴
9419𓀴After a little bit his sobs ceased, and he raised himself with an
9420𓀴apology, though he made no disguise of his emotion. He told me that for
9421𓀴days and nights past--weary days and sleepless nights--he had been
9422𓀴unable to speak with any one, as a man must speak in his time of
9423𓀴sorrow. There was no woman whose sympathy could be given to him, or with
9424𓀴whom, owing to the terrible circumstance with which his sorrow was
9425𓀴surrounded, he could speak freely. “I know now how I suffered,” he said,
9426𓀴as he dried his eyes, “but I do not know even yet--and none other can
9427𓀴ever know--how much your sweet sympathy has been to me to-day. I shall
9428𓀴know better in time; and believe me that, though I am not ungrateful
9429𓀴now, my gratitude will grow with my understanding. You will let me be
9430𓀴like a brother, will you not, for all our lives--for dear Lucy’s sake?”
9431𓀴
9432𓀴“For dear Lucy’s sake,” I said as we clasped hands. “Ay, and for your
9433𓀴own sake,” he added, “for if a man’s esteem and gratitude are ever worth
9434𓀴the winning, you have won mine to-day. If ever the future should bring
9435𓀴to you a time when you need a man’s help, believe me, you will not call
9436𓀴in vain. God grant that no such time may ever come to you to break the
9437𓀴sunshine of your life; but if it should ever come, promise me that you
9438𓀴will let me know.” He was so earnest, and his sorrow was so fresh, that
9439𓀴I felt it would comfort him, so I said:--
9440𓀴
9441𓀴“I promise.”
9442𓀴
9443𓀴As I came along the corridor I saw Mr. Morris looking out of a window.
9444𓀴He turned as he heard my footsteps. “How is Art?” he said. Then noticing
9445𓀴my red eyes, he went on: “Ah, I see you have been comforting him. Poor
9446𓀴old fellow! he needs it. No one but a woman can help a man when he is in
9447𓀴trouble of the heart; and he had no one to comfort him.”
9448𓀴
9449𓀴He bore his own trouble so bravely that my heart bled for him. I saw the
9450𓀴manuscript in his hand, and I knew that when he read it he would realise
9451𓀴how much I knew; so I said to him:--
9452𓀴
9453𓀴“I wish I could comfort all who suffer from the heart. Will you let me
9454𓀴be your friend, and will you come to me for comfort if you need it? You
9455𓀴will know, later on, why I speak.” He saw that I was in earnest, and
9456𓀴stooping, took my hand, and raising it to his lips, kissed it. It seemed
9457𓀴but poor comfort to so brave and unselfish a soul, and impulsively I
9458𓀴bent over and kissed him. The tears rose in his eyes, and there was a
9459𓀴momentary choking in his throat; he said quite calmly:--
9460𓀴
9461𓀴“Little girl, you will never regret that true-hearted kindness, so long
9462𓀴as ever you live!” Then he went into the study to his friend.
9463𓀴
9464𓀴“Little girl!”--the very words he had used to Lucy, and oh, but he
9465𓀴proved himself a friend!
9466𓀴
9467𓀴
9468𓀴
9469𓀴
9470𓀴CHAPTER XVIII
9471𓀴
9472𓀴DR. SEWARD’S DIARY
9473𓀴
9474𓀴
9475𓀴_30 September._--I got home at five o’clock, and found that Godalming
9476𓀴and Morris had not only arrived, but had already studied the transcript
9477𓀴of the various diaries and letters which Harker and his wonderful wife
9478𓀴had made and arranged. Harker had not yet returned from his visit to the
9479𓀴carriers’ men, of whom Dr. Hennessey had written to me. Mrs. Harker gave
9480𓀴us a cup of tea, and I can honestly say that, for the first time since I
9481𓀴have lived in it, this old house seemed like _home_. When we had
9482𓀴finished, Mrs. Harker said:--
9483𓀴
9484𓀴“Dr. Seward, may I ask a favour? I want to see your patient, Mr.
9485𓀴Renfield. Do let me see him. What you have said of him in your diary
9486𓀴interests me so much!” She looked so appealing and so pretty that I
9487𓀴could not refuse her, and there was no possible reason why I should; so
9488𓀴I took her with me. When I went into the room, I told the man that a
9489𓀴lady would like to see him; to which he simply answered: “Why?”
9490𓀴
9491𓀴“She is going through the house, and wants to see every one in it,” I
9492𓀴answered. “Oh, very well,” he said; “let her come in, by all means; but
9493𓀴just wait a minute till I tidy up the place.” His method of tidying was
9494𓀴peculiar: he simply swallowed all the flies and spiders in the boxes
9495𓀴before I could stop him. It was quite evident that he feared, or was
9496𓀴jealous of, some interference. When he had got through his disgusting
9497𓀴task, he said cheerfully: “Let the lady come in,” and sat down on the
9498𓀴edge of his bed with his head down, but with his eyelids raised so that
9499𓀴he could see her as she entered. For a moment I thought that he might
9500𓀴have some homicidal intent; I remembered how quiet he had been just
9501𓀴before he attacked me in my own study, and I took care to stand where I
9502𓀴could seize him at once if he attempted to make a spring at her. She
9503𓀴came into the room with an easy gracefulness which would at once command
9504𓀴the respect of any lunatic--for easiness is one of the qualities mad
9505𓀴people most respect. She walked over to him, smiling pleasantly, and
9506𓀴held out her hand.
9507𓀴
9508𓀴“Good-evening, Mr. Renfield,” said she. “You see, I know you, for Dr.
9509𓀴Seward has told me of you.” He made no immediate reply, but eyed her all
9510𓀴over intently with a set frown on his face. This look gave way to one
9511𓀴of wonder, which merged in doubt; then, to my intense astonishment, he
9512𓀴said:--
9513𓀴
9514𓀴“You’re not the girl the doctor wanted to marry, are you? You can’t be,
9515𓀴you know, for she’s dead.” Mrs. Harker smiled sweetly as she replied:--
9516𓀴
9517𓀴“Oh no! I have a husband of my own, to whom I was married before I ever
9518𓀴saw Dr. Seward, or he me. I am Mrs. Harker.”
9519𓀴
9520𓀴“Then what are you doing here?”
9521𓀴
9522𓀴“My husband and I are staying on a visit with Dr. Seward.”
9523𓀴
9524𓀴“Then don’t stay.”
9525𓀴
9526𓀴“But why not?” I thought that this style of conversation might not be
9527𓀴pleasant to Mrs. Harker, any more than it was to me, so I joined in:--
9528𓀴
9529𓀴“How did you know I wanted to marry any one?” His reply was simply
9530𓀴contemptuous, given in a pause in which he turned his eyes from Mrs.
9531𓀴Harker to me, instantly turning them back again:--
9532𓀴
9533𓀴“What an asinine question!”
9534𓀴
9535𓀴“I don’t see that at all, Mr. Renfield,” said Mrs. Harker, at once
9536𓀴championing me. He replied to her with as much courtesy and respect as
9537𓀴he had shown contempt to me:--
9538𓀴
9539𓀴“You will, of course, understand, Mrs. Harker, that when a man is so
9540𓀴loved and honoured as our host is, everything regarding him is of
9541𓀴interest in our little community. Dr. Seward is loved not only by his
9542𓀴household and his friends, but even by his patients, who, being some of
