9543𓀴them hardly in mental equilibrium, are apt to distort causes and
9544𓀴effects. Since I myself have been an inmate of a lunatic asylum, I
9545𓀴cannot but notice that the sophistic tendencies of some of its inmates
9546𓀴lean towards the errors of _non causa_ and _ignoratio elenchi_.” I
9547𓀴positively opened my eyes at this new development. Here was my own pet
9548𓀴lunatic--the most pronounced of his type that I had ever met
9549𓀴with--talking elemental philosophy, and with the manner of a polished
9550𓀴gentleman. I wonder if it was Mrs. Harker’s presence which had touched
9551𓀴some chord in his memory. If this new phase was spontaneous, or in any
9552𓀴way due to her unconscious influence, she must have some rare gift or
9553𓀴power.
9554𓀴
9555𓀴We continued to talk for some time; and, seeing that he was seemingly
9556𓀴quite reasonable, she ventured, looking at me questioningly as she
9557𓀴began, to lead him to his favourite topic. I was again astonished, for
9558𓀴he addressed himself to the question with the impartiality of the
9559𓀴completest sanity; he even took himself as an example when he mentioned
9560𓀴certain things.
9561𓀴
9562𓀴“Why, I myself am an instance of a man who had a strange belief. Indeed,
9563𓀴it was no wonder that my friends were alarmed, and insisted on my being
9564𓀴put under control. I used to fancy that life was a positive and
9565𓀴perpetual entity, and that by consuming a multitude of live things, no
9566𓀴matter how low in the scale of creation, one might indefinitely prolong
9567𓀴life. At times I held the belief so strongly that I actually tried to
9568𓀴take human life. The doctor here will bear me out that on one occasion I
9569𓀴tried to kill him for the purpose of strengthening my vital powers by
9570𓀴the assimilation with my own body of his life through the medium of his
9571𓀴blood--relying, of course, upon the Scriptural phrase, ‘For the blood is
9572𓀴the life.’ Though, indeed, the vendor of a certain nostrum has
9573𓀴vulgarised the truism to the very point of contempt. Isn’t that true,
9574𓀴doctor?” I nodded assent, for I was so amazed that I hardly knew what to
9575𓀴either think or say; it was hard to imagine that I had seen him eat up
9576𓀴his spiders and flies not five minutes before. Looking at my watch, I
9577𓀴saw that I should go to the station to meet Van Helsing, so I told Mrs.
9578𓀴Harker that it was time to leave. She came at once, after saying
9579𓀴pleasantly to Mr. Renfield: “Good-bye, and I hope I may see you often,
9580𓀴under auspices pleasanter to yourself,” to which, to my astonishment, he
9581𓀴replied:--
9582𓀴
9583𓀴“Good-bye, my dear. I pray God I may never see your sweet face again.
9584𓀴May He bless and keep you!”
9585𓀴
9586𓀴When I went to the station to meet Van Helsing I left the boys behind
9587𓀴me. Poor Art seemed more cheerful than he has been since Lucy first took
9588𓀴ill, and Quincey is more like his own bright self than he has been for
9589𓀴many a long day.
9590𓀴
9591𓀴Van Helsing stepped from the carriage with the eager nimbleness of a
9592𓀴boy. He saw me at once, and rushed up to me, saying:--
9593𓀴
9594𓀴“Ah, friend John, how goes all? Well? So! I have been busy, for I come
9595𓀴here to stay if need be. All affairs are settled with me, and I have
9596𓀴much to tell. Madam Mina is with you? Yes. And her so fine husband? And
9597𓀴Arthur and my friend Quincey, they are with you, too? Good!”
9598𓀴
9599𓀴As I drove to the house I told him of what had passed, and of how my own
9600𓀴diary had come to be of some use through Mrs. Harker’s suggestion; at
9601𓀴which the Professor interrupted me:--
9602𓀴
9603𓀴“Ah, that wonderful Madam Mina! She has man’s brain--a brain that a man
9604𓀴should have were he much gifted--and a woman’s heart. The good God
9605𓀴fashioned her for a purpose, believe me, when He made that so good
9606𓀴combination. Friend John, up to now fortune has made that woman of help
9607𓀴to us; after to-night she must not have to do with this so terrible
9608𓀴affair. It is not good that she run a risk so great. We men are
9609𓀴determined--nay, are we not pledged?--to destroy this monster; but it is
9610𓀴no part for a woman. Even if she be not harmed, her heart may fail her
9611𓀴in so much and so many horrors; and hereafter she may suffer--both in
9612𓀴waking, from her nerves, and in sleep, from her dreams. And, besides,
9613𓀴she is young woman and not so long married; there may be other things to
9614𓀴think of some time, if not now. You tell me she has wrote all, then she
9615𓀴must consult with us; but to-morrow she say good-bye to this work, and
9616𓀴we go alone.” I agreed heartily with him, and then I told him what we
9617𓀴had found in his absence: that the house which Dracula had bought was
9618𓀴the very next one to my own. He was amazed, and a great concern seemed
9619𓀴to come on him. “Oh that we had known it before!” he said, “for then we
9620𓀴might have reached him in time to save poor Lucy. However, ‘the milk
9621𓀴that is spilt cries not out afterwards,’ as you say. We shall not think
9622𓀴of that, but go on our way to the end.” Then he fell into a silence that
9623𓀴lasted till we entered my own gateway. Before we went to prepare for
9624𓀴dinner he said to Mrs. Harker:--
9625𓀴
9626𓀴“I am told, Madam Mina, by my friend John that you and your husband have
9627𓀴put up in exact order all things that have been, up to this moment.”
9628𓀴
9629𓀴“Not up to this moment, Professor,” she said impulsively, “but up to
9630𓀴this morning.”
9631𓀴
9632𓀴“But why not up to now? We have seen hitherto how good light all the
9633𓀴little things have made. We have told our secrets, and yet no one who
9634𓀴has told is the worse for it.”
9635𓀴
9636𓀴Mrs. Harker began to blush, and taking a paper from her pockets, she
9637𓀴said:--
9638𓀴
9639𓀴“Dr. Van Helsing, will you read this, and tell me if it must go in. It
9640𓀴is my record of to-day. I too have seen the need of putting down at
9641𓀴present everything, however trivial; but there is little in this except
9642𓀴what is personal. Must it go in?” The Professor read it over gravely,
9643𓀴and handed it back, saying:--
9644𓀴
9645𓀴“It need not go in if you do not wish it; but I pray that it may. It can
9646𓀴but make your husband love you the more, and all us, your friends, more
9647𓀴honour you--as well as more esteem and love.” She took it back with
9648𓀴another blush and a bright smile.
9649𓀴
9650𓀴And so now, up to this very hour, all the records we have are complete
9651𓀴and in order. The Professor took away one copy to study after dinner,
9652𓀴and before our meeting, which is fixed for nine o’clock. The rest of us
9653𓀴have already read everything; so when we meet in the study we shall all
9654𓀴be informed as to facts, and can arrange our plan of battle with this
9655𓀴terrible and mysterious enemy.
9656𓀴
9657𓀴
9658𓀴_Mina Harker’s Journal._
9659𓀴
9660𓀴_30 September._--When we met in Dr. Seward’s study two hours after
9661𓀴dinner, which had been at six o’clock, we unconsciously formed a sort of
9662𓀴board or committee. Professor Van Helsing took the head of the table, to
9663𓀴which Dr. Seward motioned him as he came into the room. He made me sit
9664𓀴next to him on his right, and asked me to act as secretary; Jonathan sat
9665𓀴next to me. Opposite us were Lord Godalming, Dr. Seward, and Mr.
9666𓀴Morris--Lord Godalming being next the Professor, and Dr. Seward in the
9667𓀴centre. The Professor said:--
9668𓀴
9669𓀴“I may, I suppose, take it that we are all acquainted with the facts
9670𓀴that are in these papers.” We all expressed assent, and he went on:--
9671𓀴
9672𓀴“Then it were, I think good that I tell you something of the kind of
9673𓀴enemy with which we have to deal. I shall then make known to you
9674𓀴something of the history of this man, which has been ascertained for me.
9675𓀴So we then can discuss how we shall act, and can take our measure
9676𓀴according.
9677𓀴
9678𓀴“There are such beings as vampires; some of us have evidence that they
9679𓀴exist. Even had we not the proof of our own unhappy experience, the
9680𓀴teachings and the records of the past give proof enough for sane
9681𓀴peoples. I admit that at the first I was sceptic. Were it not that
9682𓀴through long years I have train myself to keep an open mind, I could not
9683𓀴have believe until such time as that fact thunder on my ear. ‘See! see!
9684𓀴I prove; I prove.’ Alas! Had I known at the first what now I know--nay,
9685𓀴had I even guess at him--one so precious life had been spared to many of
9686𓀴us who did love her. But that is gone; and we must so work, that other
9687𓀴poor souls perish not, whilst we can save. The _nosferatu_ do not die
9688𓀴like the bee when he sting once. He is only stronger; and being
9689𓀴stronger, have yet more power to work evil. This vampire which is
9690𓀴amongst us is of himself so strong in person as twenty men; he is of
9691𓀴cunning more than mortal, for his cunning be the growth of ages; he have
9692𓀴still the aids of necromancy, which is, as his etymology imply, the
9693𓀴divination by the dead, and all the dead that he can come nigh to are
9694𓀴for him at command; he is brute, and more than brute; he is devil in
9695𓀴callous, and the heart of him is not; he can, within limitations, appear
9696𓀴at will when, and where, and in any of the forms that are to him; he
9697𓀴can, within his range, direct the elements; the storm, the fog, the
9698𓀴thunder; he can command all the meaner things: the rat, and the owl, and
9699𓀴the bat--the moth, and the fox, and the wolf; he can grow and become
9700𓀴small; and he can at times vanish and come unknown. How then are we to
9701𓀴begin our strike to destroy him? How shall we find his where; and having
9702𓀴found it, how can we destroy? My friends, this is much; it is a terrible
9703𓀴task that we undertake, and there may be consequence to make the brave
9704𓀴shudder. For if we fail in this our fight he must surely win; and then
9705𓀴where end we? Life is nothings; I heed him not. But to fail here, is not
9706𓀴mere life or death. It is that we become as him; that we henceforward
9707𓀴become foul things of the night like him--without heart or conscience,
9708𓀴preying on the bodies and the souls of those we love best. To us for
9709𓀴ever are the gates of heaven shut; for who shall open them to us again?
9710𓀴We go on for all time abhorred by all; a blot on the face of God’s
9711𓀴sunshine; an arrow in the side of Him who died for man. But we are face
9712𓀴to face with duty; and in such case must we shrink? For me, I say, no;
9713𓀴but then I am old, and life, with his sunshine, his fair places, his
9714𓀴song of birds, his music and his love, lie far behind. You others are
9715𓀴young. Some have seen sorrow; but there are fair days yet in store. What
9716𓀴say you?”
9717𓀴
9718𓀴Whilst he was speaking, Jonathan had taken my hand. I feared, oh so
9719𓀴much, that the appalling nature of our danger was overcoming him when I
9720𓀴saw his hand stretch out; but it was life to me to feel its touch--so
9721𓀴strong, so self-reliant, so resolute. A brave man’s hand can speak for
9722𓀴itself; it does not even need a woman’s love to hear its music.
9723𓀴
9724𓀴When the Professor had done speaking my husband looked in my eyes, and I
9725𓀴in his; there was no need for speaking between us.
9726𓀴
9727𓀴“I answer for Mina and myself,” he said.
9728𓀴
9729𓀴“Count me in, Professor,” said Mr. Quincey Morris, laconically as usual.
9730𓀴
9731𓀴“I am with you,” said Lord Godalming, “for Lucy’s sake, if for no other
9732𓀴reason.”
9733𓀴
9734𓀴Dr. Seward simply nodded. The Professor stood up and, after laying his
9735𓀴golden crucifix on the table, held out his hand on either side. I took
9736𓀴his right hand, and Lord Godalming his left; Jonathan held my right with
9737𓀴his left and stretched across to Mr. Morris. So as we all took hands our
9738𓀴solemn compact was made. I felt my heart icy cold, but it did not even
9739𓀴occur to me to draw back. We resumed our places, and Dr. Van Helsing
9740𓀴went on with a sort of cheerfulness which showed that the serious work
9741𓀴had begun. It was to be taken as gravely, and in as businesslike a way,
9742𓀴as any other transaction of life:--
9743𓀴
9744𓀴“Well, you know what we have to contend against; but we, too, are not
9745𓀴without strength. We have on our side power of combination--a power
9746𓀴denied to the vampire kind; we have sources of science; we are free to
9747𓀴act and think; and the hours of the day and the night are ours equally.
9748𓀴In fact, so far as our powers extend, they are unfettered, and we are
9749𓀴free to use them. We have self-devotion in a cause, and an end to
9750𓀴achieve which is not a selfish one. These things are much.
9751𓀴
9752𓀴“Now let us see how far the general powers arrayed against us are
9753𓀴restrict, and how the individual cannot. In fine, let us consider the
9754𓀴limitations of the vampire in general, and of this one in particular.
9755𓀴
9756𓀴“All we have to go upon are traditions and superstitions. These do not
9757𓀴at the first appear much, when the matter is one of life and death--nay
9758𓀴of more than either life or death. Yet must we be satisfied; in the
9759𓀴first place because we have to be--no other means is at our control--and
9760𓀴secondly, because, after all, these things--tradition and
9761𓀴superstition--are everything. Does not the belief in vampires rest for
9762𓀴others--though not, alas! for us--on them? A year ago which of us would
9763𓀴have received such a possibility, in the midst of our scientific,
9764𓀴sceptical, matter-of-fact nineteenth century? We even scouted a belief
9765𓀴that we saw justified under our very eyes. Take it, then, that the
9766𓀴vampire, and the belief in his limitations and his cure, rest for the
9767𓀴moment on the same base. For, let me tell you, he is known everywhere
9768𓀴that men have been. In old Greece, in old Rome; he flourish in Germany
9769𓀴all over, in France, in India, even in the Chernosese; and in China, so
9770𓀴far from us in all ways, there even is he, and the peoples fear him at
9771𓀴this day. He have follow the wake of the berserker Icelander, the
9772𓀴devil-begotten Hun, the Slav, the Saxon, the Magyar. So far, then, we
9773𓀴have all we may act upon; and let me tell you that very much of the
9774𓀴beliefs are justified by what we have seen in our own so unhappy
9775𓀴experience. The vampire live on, and cannot die by mere passing of the
9776𓀴time; he can flourish when that he can fatten on the blood of the
9777𓀴living. Even more, we have seen amongst us that he can even grow
9778𓀴younger; that his vital faculties grow strenuous, and seem as though
9779𓀴they refresh themselves when his special pabulum is plenty. But he
9780𓀴cannot flourish without this diet; he eat not as others. Even friend
9781𓀴Jonathan, who lived with him for weeks, did never see him to eat, never!
9782𓀴He throws no shadow; he make in the mirror no reflect, as again
9783𓀴Jonathan observe. He has the strength of many of his hand--witness again
9784𓀴Jonathan when he shut the door against the wolfs, and when he help him
9785𓀴from the diligence too. He can transform himself to wolf, as we gather
9786𓀴from the ship arrival in Whitby, when he tear open the dog; he can be as
9787𓀴bat, as Madam Mina saw him on the window at Whitby, and as friend John
9788𓀴saw him fly from this so near house, and as my friend Quincey saw him at
9789𓀴the window of Miss Lucy. He can come in mist which he create--that noble
9790𓀴ship’s captain proved him of this; but, from what we know, the distance
9791𓀴he can make this mist is limited, and it can only be round himself. He
9792𓀴come on moonlight rays as elemental dust--as again Jonathan saw those
9793𓀴sisters in the castle of Dracula. He become so small--we ourselves saw
9794𓀴Miss Lucy, ere she was at peace, slip through a hairbreadth space at the
9795𓀴tomb door. He can, when once he find his way, come out from anything or
9796𓀴into anything, no matter how close it be bound or even fused up with
9797𓀴fire--solder you call it. He can see in the dark--no small power this,
9798𓀴in a world which is one half shut from the light. Ah, but hear me
9799𓀴through. He can do all these things, yet he is not free. Nay; he is even
9800𓀴more prisoner than the slave of the galley, than the madman in his cell.
9801𓀴He cannot go where he lists; he who is not of nature has yet to obey
9802𓀴some of nature’s laws--why we know not. He may not enter anywhere at the
9803𓀴first, unless there be some one of the household who bid him to come;
9804𓀴though afterwards he can come as he please. His power ceases, as does
9805𓀴that of all evil things, at the coming of the day. Only at certain times
9806𓀴can he have limited freedom. If he be not at the place whither he is
9807𓀴bound, he can only change himself at noon or at exact sunrise or sunset.
9808𓀴These things are we told, and in this record of ours we have proof by
9809𓀴inference. Thus, whereas he can do as he will within his limit, when he
9810𓀴have his earth-home, his coffin-home, his hell-home, the place
9811𓀴unhallowed, as we saw when he went to the grave of the suicide at
9812𓀴Whitby; still at other time he can only change when the time come. It is
9813𓀴said, too, that he can only pass running water at the slack or the flood
9814𓀴of the tide. Then there are things which so afflict him that he has no
9815𓀴power, as the garlic that we know of; and as for things sacred, as this
9816𓀴symbol, my crucifix, that was amongst us even now when we resolve, to
9817𓀴them he is nothing, but in their presence he take his place far off and
9818𓀴silent with respect. There are others, too, which I shall tell you of,
9819𓀴lest in our seeking we may need them. The branch of wild rose on his
9820𓀴coffin keep him that he move not from it; a sacred bullet fired into the
9821𓀴coffin kill him so that he be true dead; and as for the stake through
9822𓀴him, we know already of its peace; or the cut-off head that giveth rest.
9823𓀴We have seen it with our eyes.
9824𓀴
9825𓀴“Thus when we find the habitation of this man-that-was, we can confine
9826𓀴him to his coffin and destroy him, if we obey what we know. But he is
9827𓀴clever. I have asked my friend Arminius, of Buda-Pesth University, to
9828𓀴make his record; and, from all the means that are, he tell me of what he
9829𓀴has been. He must, indeed, have been that Voivode Dracula who won his
9830𓀴name against the Turk, over the great river on the very frontier of
9831𓀴Turkey-land. If it be so, then was he no common man; for in that time,
9832𓀴and for centuries after, he was spoken of as the cleverest and the most
9833𓀴cunning, as well as the bravest of the sons of the ‘land beyond the
9834𓀴forest.’ That mighty brain and that iron resolution went with him to his
9835𓀴grave, and are even now arrayed against us. The Draculas were, says
9836𓀴Arminius, a great and noble race, though now and again were scions who
9837𓀴were held by their coevals to have had dealings with the Evil One. They
9838𓀴learned his secrets in the Scholomance, amongst the mountains over Lake
9839𓀴Hermanstadt, where the devil claims the tenth scholar as his due. In the
9840𓀴records are such words as ‘stregoica’--witch, ‘ordog,’ and
9841𓀴‘pokol’--Satan and hell; and in one manuscript this very Dracula is
9842𓀴spoken of as ‘wampyr,’ which we all understand too well. There have been
9843𓀴from the loins of this very one great men and good women, and their
9844𓀴graves make sacred the earth where alone this foulness can dwell. For it
9845𓀴is not the least of its terrors that this evil thing is rooted deep in
9846𓀴all good; in soil barren of holy memories it cannot rest.”
9847𓀴
9848𓀴Whilst they were talking Mr. Morris was looking steadily at the window,
9849𓀴and he now got up quietly, and went out of the room. There was a little
9850𓀴pause, and then the Professor went on:--
9851𓀴
9852𓀴“And now we must settle what we do. We have here much data, and we must
9853𓀴proceed to lay out our campaign. We know from the inquiry of Jonathan
9854𓀴that from the castle to Whitby came fifty boxes of earth, all of which
9855𓀴were delivered at Carfax; we also know that at least some of these boxes
9856𓀴have been removed. It seems to me, that our first step should be to
9857𓀴ascertain whether all the rest remain in the house beyond that wall
9858𓀴where we look to-day; or whether any more have been removed. If the
9859𓀴latter, we must trace----”
9860𓀴
9861𓀴Here we were interrupted in a very startling way. Outside the house came
9862𓀴the sound of a pistol-shot; the glass of the window was shattered with a
9863𓀴bullet, which, ricochetting from the top of the embrasure, struck the
9864𓀴far wall of the room. I am afraid I am at heart a coward, for I shrieked
9865𓀴out. The men all jumped to their feet; Lord Godalming flew over to the
9866𓀴window and threw up the sash. As he did so we heard Mr. Morris’s voice
9867𓀴without:--
9868𓀴
9869𓀴“Sorry! I fear I have alarmed you. I shall come in and tell you about
9870𓀴it.” A minute later he came in and said:--
9871𓀴
9872𓀴“It was an idiotic thing of me to do, and I ask your pardon, Mrs.
9873𓀴Harker, most sincerely; I fear I must have frightened you terribly. But
9874𓀴the fact is that whilst the Professor was talking there came a big bat
9875𓀴and sat on the window-sill. I have got such a horror of the damned
9876𓀴brutes from recent events that I cannot stand them, and I went out to
9877𓀴have a shot, as I have been doing of late of evenings, whenever I have
9878𓀴seen one. You used to laugh at me for it then, Art.”
9879𓀴
9880𓀴“Did you hit it?” asked Dr. Van Helsing.
9881𓀴
9882𓀴“I don’t know; I fancy not, for it flew away into the wood.” Without
9883𓀴saying any more he took his seat, and the Professor began to resume his
9884𓀴statement:--
9885𓀴
9886𓀴“We must trace each of these boxes; and when we are ready, we must
9887𓀴either capture or kill this monster in his lair; or we must, so to
9888𓀴speak, sterilise the earth, so that no more he can seek safety in it.
9889𓀴Thus in the end we may find him in his form of man between the hours of
9890𓀴noon and sunset, and so engage with him when he is at his most weak.
9891𓀴
9892𓀴“And now for you, Madam Mina, this night is the end until all be well.
9893𓀴You are too precious to us to have such risk. When we part to-night, you
9894𓀴no more must question. We shall tell you all in good time. We are men
9895𓀴and are able to bear; but you must be our star and our hope, and we
9896𓀴shall act all the more free that you are not in the danger, such as we
9897𓀴are.”
9898𓀴
9899𓀴All the men, even Jonathan, seemed relieved; but it did not seem to me
9900𓀴good that they should brave danger and, perhaps, lessen their
9901𓀴safety--strength being the best safety--through care of me; but their
9902𓀴minds were made up, and, though it was a bitter pill for me to swallow,
9903𓀴I could say nothing, save to accept their chivalrous care of me.
9904𓀴
9905𓀴Mr. Morris resumed the discussion:--
9906𓀴
9907𓀴“As there is no time to lose, I vote we have a look at his house right
9908𓀴now. Time is everything with him; and swift action on our part may save
9909𓀴another victim.”
9910𓀴
9911𓀴I own that my heart began to fail me when the time for action came so
9912𓀴close, but I did not say anything, for I had a greater fear that if I
9913𓀴appeared as a drag or a hindrance to their work, they might even leave
9914𓀴me out of their counsels altogether. They have now gone off to Carfax,
9915𓀴with means to get into the house.
9916𓀴
9917𓀴Manlike, they had told me to go to bed and sleep; as if a woman can
9918𓀴sleep when those she loves are in danger! I shall lie down and pretend
9919𓀴to sleep, lest Jonathan have added anxiety about me when he returns.
9920𓀴
9921𓀴
9922𓀴_Dr. Seward’s Diary._
9923𓀴
9924𓀴_1 October, 4 a. m._--Just as we were about to leave the house, an
9925𓀴urgent message was brought to me from Renfield to know if I would see
9926𓀴him at once, as he had something of the utmost importance to say to me.
9927𓀴I told the messenger to say that I would attend to his wishes in the
9928𓀴morning; I was busy just at the moment. The attendant added:--
9929𓀴
9930𓀴“He seems very importunate, sir. I have never seen him so eager. I don’t
9931𓀴know but what, if you don’t see him soon, he will have one of his
9932𓀴violent fits.” I knew the man would not have said this without some
9933𓀴cause, so I said: “All right; I’ll go now”; and I asked the others to
9934𓀴wait a few minutes for me, as I had to go and see my “patient.”
9935𓀴
9936𓀴“Take me with you, friend John,” said the Professor. “His case in your
9937𓀴diary interest me much, and it had bearing, too, now and again on _our_
9938𓀴case. I should much like to see him, and especial when his mind is
9939𓀴disturbed.”
9940𓀴
9941𓀴“May I come also?” asked Lord Godalming.
9942𓀴
9943𓀴“Me too?” said Quincey Morris. “May I come?” said Harker. I nodded, and
9944𓀴we all went down the passage together.
9945𓀴
9946𓀴We found him in a state of considerable excitement, but far more
9947𓀴rational in his speech and manner than I had ever seen him. There was an
9948𓀴unusual understanding of himself, which was unlike anything I had ever
9949𓀴met with in a lunatic; and he took it for granted that his reasons would
9950𓀴prevail with others entirely sane. We all four went into the room, but
9951𓀴none of the others at first said anything. His request was that I would
9952𓀴at once release him from the asylum and send him home. This he backed up
9953𓀴with arguments regarding his complete recovery, and adduced his own
9954𓀴existing sanity. “I appeal to your friends,” he said, “they will,
9955𓀴perhaps, not mind sitting in judgment on my case. By the way, you have
9956𓀴not introduced me.” I was so much astonished, that the oddness of
9957𓀴introducing a madman in an asylum did not strike me at the moment; and,
9958𓀴besides, there was a certain dignity in the man’s manner, so much of
9959𓀴the habit of equality, that I at once made the introduction: “Lord
9960𓀴Godalming; Professor Van Helsing; Mr. Quincey Morris, of Texas; Mr.
9961𓀴Renfield.” He shook hands with each of them, saying in turn:--
9962𓀴
9963𓀴“Lord Godalming, I had the honour of seconding your father at the
9964𓀴Windham; I grieve to know, by your holding the title, that he is no
9965𓀴more. He was a man loved and honoured by all who knew him; and in his
9966𓀴youth was, I have heard, the inventor of a burnt rum punch, much
9967𓀴patronised on Derby night. Mr. Morris, you should be proud of your great
9968𓀴state. Its reception into the Union was a precedent which may have
9969𓀴far-reaching effects hereafter, when the Pole and the Tropics may hold
9970𓀴alliance to the Stars and Stripes. The power of Treaty may yet prove a
9971𓀴vast engine of enlargement, when the Monroe doctrine takes its true
9972𓀴place as a political fable. What shall any man say of his pleasure at
9973𓀴meeting Van Helsing? Sir, I make no apology for dropping all forms of
9974𓀴conventional prefix. When an individual has revolutionised therapeutics
9975𓀴by his discovery of the continuous evolution of brain-matter,
9976𓀴conventional forms are unfitting, since they would seem to limit him to
9977𓀴one of a class. You, gentlemen, who by nationality, by heredity, or by
9978𓀴the possession of natural gifts, are fitted to hold your respective
9979𓀴places in the moving world, I take to witness that I am as sane as at
9980𓀴least the majority of men who are in full possession of their liberties.
9981𓀴And I am sure that you, Dr. Seward, humanitarian and medico-jurist as
9982𓀴well as scientist, will deem it a moral duty to deal with me as one to
9983𓀴be considered as under exceptional circumstances.” He made this last
9984𓀴appeal with a courtly air of conviction which was not without its own
9985𓀴charm.
9986𓀴
9987𓀴I think we were all staggered. For my own part, I was under the
9988𓀴conviction, despite my knowledge of the man’s character and history,
9989𓀴that his reason had been restored; and I felt under a strong impulse to
9990𓀴tell him that I was satisfied as to his sanity, and would see about the
9991𓀴necessary formalities for his release in the morning. I thought it
9992𓀴better to wait, however, before making so grave a statement, for of old
9993𓀴I knew the sudden changes to which this particular patient was liable.
9994𓀴So I contented myself with making a general statement that he appeared
9995𓀴to be improving very rapidly; that I would have a longer chat with him
9996𓀴in the morning, and would then see what I could do in the direction of
9997𓀴meeting his wishes. This did not at all satisfy him, for he said
9998𓀴quickly:--
9999𓀴
10000𓀴“But I fear, Dr. Seward, that you hardly apprehend my wish. I desire to
10001𓀴go at once--here--now--this very hour--this very moment, if I may. Time
10002𓀴presses, and in our implied agreement with the old scytheman it is of
10003𓀴the essence of the contract. I am sure it is only necessary to put
10004𓀴before so admirable a practitioner as Dr. Seward so simple, yet so
10005𓀴momentous a wish, to ensure its fulfilment.” He looked at me keenly, and
10006𓀴seeing the negative in my face, turned to the others, and scrutinised
10007𓀴them closely. Not meeting any sufficient response, he went on:--
10008𓀴
10009𓀴“Is it possible that I have erred in my supposition?”
10010𓀴
10011𓀴“You have,” I said frankly, but at the same time, as I felt, brutally.
10012𓀴There was a considerable pause, and then he said slowly:--
10013𓀴
10014𓀴“Then I suppose I must only shift my ground of request. Let me ask for
10015𓀴this concession--boon, privilege, what you will. I am content to implore
10016𓀴in such a case, not on personal grounds, but for the sake of others. I
10017𓀴am not at liberty to give you the whole of my reasons; but you may, I
10018𓀴assure you, take it from me that they are good ones, sound and
10019𓀴unselfish, and spring from the highest sense of duty. Could you look,
10020𓀴sir, into my heart, you would approve to the full the sentiments which
10021𓀴animate me. Nay, more, you would count me amongst the best and truest of
10022𓀴your friends.” Again he looked at us all keenly. I had a growing
10023𓀴conviction that this sudden change of his entire intellectual method was
10024𓀴but yet another form or phase of his madness, and so determined to let
10025𓀴him go on a little longer, knowing from experience that he would, like
10026𓀴all lunatics, give himself away in the end. Van Helsing was gazing at
10027𓀴him with a look of utmost intensity, his bushy eyebrows almost meeting
10028𓀴with the fixed concentration of his look. He said to Renfield in a tone
10029𓀴which did not surprise me at the time, but only when I thought of it
10030𓀴afterwards--for it was as of one addressing an equal:--
10031𓀴
10032𓀴“Can you not tell frankly your real reason for wishing to be free
10033𓀴to-night? I will undertake that if you will satisfy even me--a stranger,
10034𓀴without prejudice, and with the habit of keeping an open mind--Dr.
10035𓀴Seward will give you, at his own risk and on his own responsibility, the
10036𓀴privilege you seek.” He shook his head sadly, and with a look of
10037𓀴poignant regret on his face. The Professor went on:--
10038𓀴
10039𓀴“Come, sir, bethink yourself. You claim the privilege of reason in the
10040𓀴highest degree, since you seek to impress us with your complete
10041𓀴reasonableness. You do this, whose sanity we have reason to doubt, since
10042𓀴you are not yet released from medical treatment for this very defect. If
10043𓀴you will not help us in our effort to choose the wisest course, how can
10044𓀴we perform the duty which you yourself put upon us? Be wise, and help
10045𓀴us; and if we can we shall aid you to achieve your wish.” He still shook
10046𓀴his head as he said:--
10047𓀴
10048𓀴“Dr. Van Helsing, I have nothing to say. Your argument is complete, and
10049𓀴if I were free to speak I should not hesitate a moment; but I am not my
10050𓀴own master in the matter. I can only ask you to trust me. If I am
10051𓀴refused, the responsibility does not rest with me.” I thought it was now
10052𓀴time to end the scene, which was becoming too comically grave, so I went
10053𓀴towards the door, simply saying:--
10054𓀴
10055𓀴“Come, my friends, we have work to do. Good-night.”
10056𓀴
10057𓀴As, however, I got near the door, a new change came over the patient. He
10058𓀴moved towards me so quickly that for the moment I feared that he was
10059𓀴about to make another homicidal attack. My fears, however, were
10060𓀴groundless, for he held up his two hands imploringly, and made his
10061𓀴petition in a moving manner. As he saw that the very excess of his
10062𓀴emotion was militating against him, by restoring us more to our old
10063𓀴relations, he became still more demonstrative. I glanced at Van Helsing,
10064𓀴and saw my conviction reflected in his eyes; so I became a little more
10065𓀴fixed in my manner, if not more stern, and motioned to him that his
10066𓀴efforts were unavailing. I had previously seen something of the same
10067𓀴constantly growing excitement in him when he had to make some request of
10068𓀴which at the time he had thought much, such, for instance, as when he
10069𓀴wanted a cat; and I was prepared to see the collapse into the same
10070𓀴sullen acquiescence on this occasion. My expectation was not realised,
10071𓀴for, when he found that his appeal would not be successful, he got into
10072𓀴quite a frantic condition. He threw himself on his knees, and held up
10073𓀴his hands, wringing them in plaintive supplication, and poured forth a
10074𓀴torrent of entreaty, with the tears rolling down his cheeks, and his
10075𓀴whole face and form expressive of the deepest emotion:--
10076𓀴
10077𓀴“Let me entreat you, Dr. Seward, oh, let me implore you, to let me out
10078𓀴of this house at once. Send me away how you will and where you will;
10079𓀴send keepers with me with whips and chains; let them take me in a
10080𓀴strait-waistcoat, manacled and leg-ironed, even to a gaol; but let me go
10081𓀴out of this. You don’t know what you do by keeping me here. I am
10082𓀴speaking from the depths of my heart--of my very soul. You don’t know
10083𓀴whom you wrong, or how; and I may not tell. Woe is me! I may not tell.
10084𓀴By all you hold sacred--by all you hold dear--by your love that is
10085𓀴lost--by your hope that lives--for the sake of the Almighty, take me out
10086𓀴of this and save my soul from guilt! Can’t you hear me, man? Can’t you
10087𓀴understand? Will you never learn? Don’t you know that I am sane and
10088𓀴earnest now; that I am no lunatic in a mad fit, but a sane man fighting
10089𓀴for his soul? Oh, hear me! hear me! Let me go! let me go! let me go!”
10090𓀴
10091𓀴I thought that the longer this went on the wilder he would get, and so
10092𓀴would bring on a fit; so I took him by the hand and raised him up.
10093𓀴
10094𓀴“Come,” I said sternly, “no more of this; we have had quite enough
10095𓀴already. Get to your bed and try to behave more discreetly.”
10096𓀴
10097𓀴He suddenly stopped and looked at me intently for several moments. Then,
10098𓀴without a word, he rose and moving over, sat down on the side of the
10099𓀴bed. The collapse had come, as on former occasion, just as I had
10100𓀴expected.
10101𓀴
10102𓀴When I was leaving the room, last of our party, he said to me in a
10103𓀴quiet, well-bred voice:--
10104𓀴
10105𓀴“You will, I trust, Dr. Seward, do me the justice to bear in mind, later
10106𓀴on, that I did what I could to convince you to-night.”
10107𓀴
10108𓀴
10109𓀴
10110𓀴
10111𓀴CHAPTER XIX
10112𓀴
10113𓀴JONATHAN HARKER’S JOURNAL
10114𓀴
10115𓀴
10116𓀴_1 October, 5 a. m._--I went with the party to the search with an easy
10117𓀴mind, for I think I never saw Mina so absolutely strong and well. I am
10118𓀴so glad that she consented to hold back and let us men do the work.
10119𓀴Somehow, it was a dread to me that she was in this fearful business at
10120𓀴all; but now that her work is done, and that it is due to her energy and
10121𓀴brains and foresight that the whole story is put together in such a way
10122𓀴that every point tells, she may well feel that her part is finished, and
10123𓀴that she can henceforth leave the rest to us. We were, I think, all a
10124𓀴little upset by the scene with Mr. Renfield. When we came away from his
10125𓀴room we were silent till we got back to the study. Then Mr. Morris said
10126𓀴to Dr. Seward:--
10127𓀴
10128𓀴“Say, Jack, if that man wasn’t attempting a bluff, he is about the
10129𓀴sanest lunatic I ever saw. I’m not sure, but I believe that he had some
10130𓀴serious purpose, and if he had, it was pretty rough on him not to get a
10131𓀴chance.” Lord Godalming and I were silent, but Dr. Van Helsing added:--
10132𓀴
10133𓀴“Friend John, you know more of lunatics than I do, and I’m glad of it,
10134𓀴for I fear that if it had been to me to decide I would before that last
10135𓀴hysterical outburst have given him free. But we live and learn, and in
10136𓀴our present task we must take no chance, as my friend Quincey would say.
10137𓀴All is best as they are.” Dr. Seward seemed to answer them both in a
10138𓀴dreamy kind of way:--
10139𓀴
10140𓀴“I don’t know but that I agree with you. If that man had been an
10141𓀴ordinary lunatic I would have taken my chance of trusting him; but he
10142𓀴seems so mixed up with the Count in an indexy kind of way that I am
10143𓀴afraid of doing anything wrong by helping his fads. I can’t forget how
10144𓀴he prayed with almost equal fervour for a cat, and then tried to tear my
10145𓀴throat out with his teeth. Besides, he called the Count ‘lord and
10146𓀴master,’ and he may want to get out to help him in some diabolical way.
10147𓀴That horrid thing has the wolves and the rats and his own kind to help
10148𓀴him, so I suppose he isn’t above trying to use a respectable lunatic. He
10149𓀴certainly did seem earnest, though. I only hope we have done what is
10150𓀴best. These things, in conjunction with the wild work we have in hand,
10151𓀴help to unnerve a man.” The Professor stepped over, and laying his hand
10152𓀴on his shoulder, said in his grave, kindly way:--
10153𓀴
10154𓀴“Friend John, have no fear. We are trying to do our duty in a very sad
10155𓀴and terrible case; we can only do as we deem best. What else have we to
10156𓀴hope for, except the pity of the good God?” Lord Godalming had slipped
10157𓀴away for a few minutes, but now he returned. He held up a little silver
10158𓀴whistle, as he remarked:--
10159𓀴
10160𓀴“That old place may be full of rats, and if so, I’ve got an antidote on
10161𓀴call.” Having passed the wall, we took our way to the house, taking care
10162𓀴to keep in the shadows of the trees on the lawn when the moonlight shone
10163𓀴out. When we got to the porch the Professor opened his bag and took out
10164𓀴a lot of things, which he laid on the step, sorting them into four
10165𓀴little groups, evidently one for each. Then he spoke:--
10166𓀴
10167𓀴“My friends, we are going into a terrible danger, and we need arms of
10168𓀴many kinds. Our enemy is not merely spiritual. Remember that he has the
10169𓀴strength of twenty men, and that, though our necks or our windpipes are
10170𓀴of the common kind--and therefore breakable or crushable--his are not
10171𓀴amenable to mere strength. A stronger man, or a body of men more strong
10172𓀴in all than him, can at certain times hold him; but they cannot hurt him
10173𓀴as we can be hurt by him. We must, therefore, guard ourselves from his
10174𓀴touch. Keep this near your heart”--as he spoke he lifted a little silver
10175𓀴crucifix and held it out to me, I being nearest to him--“put these
10176𓀴flowers round your neck”--here he handed to me a wreath of withered
10177𓀴garlic blossoms--“for other enemies more mundane, this revolver and this
10178𓀴knife; and for aid in all, these so small electric lamps, which you can
10179𓀴fasten to your breast; and for all, and above all at the last, this,
10180𓀴which we must not desecrate needless.” This was a portion of Sacred
10181𓀴Wafer, which he put in an envelope and handed to me. Each of the others
10182𓀴was similarly equipped. “Now,” he said, “friend John, where are the
10183𓀴skeleton keys? If so that we can open the door, we need not break house
10184𓀴by the window, as before at Miss Lucy’s.”
10185𓀴
10186𓀴Dr. Seward tried one or two skeleton keys, his mechanical dexterity as a
10187𓀴surgeon standing him in good stead. Presently he got one to suit; after
10188𓀴a little play back and forward the bolt yielded, and, with a rusty
10189𓀴clang, shot back. We pressed on the door, the rusty hinges creaked, and
10190𓀴it slowly opened. It was startlingly like the image conveyed to me in
10191𓀴Dr. Seward’s diary of the opening of Miss Westenra’s tomb; I fancy that
10192𓀴the same idea seemed to strike the others, for with one accord they
10193𓀴shrank back. The Professor was the first to move forward, and stepped
10194𓀴into the open door.
10195𓀴
10196𓀴“_In manus tuas, Domine!_” he said, crossing himself as he passed over
10197𓀴the threshold. We closed the door behind us, lest when we should have
10198𓀴lit our lamps we should possibly attract attention from the road. The
10199𓀴Professor carefully tried the lock, lest we might not be able to open it
10200𓀴from within should we be in a hurry making our exit. Then we all lit our
10201𓀴lamps and proceeded on our search.
10202𓀴
10203𓀴The light from the tiny lamps fell in all sorts of odd forms, as the
10204𓀴rays crossed each other, or the opacity of our bodies threw great
10205𓀴shadows. I could not for my life get away from the feeling that there
10206𓀴was some one else amongst us. I suppose it was the recollection, so
10207𓀴powerfully brought home to me by the grim surroundings, of that terrible
10208𓀴experience in Transylvania. I think the feeling was common to us all,
10209𓀴for I noticed that the others kept looking over their shoulders at every
10210𓀴sound and every new shadow, just as I felt myself doing.
10211𓀴
10212𓀴The whole place was thick with dust. The floor was seemingly inches
10213𓀴deep, except where there were recent footsteps, in which on holding down
10214𓀴my lamp I could see marks of hobnails where the dust was cracked. The
10215𓀴walls were fluffy and heavy with dust, and in the corners were masses of
10216𓀴spider’s webs, whereon the dust had gathered till they looked like old
10217𓀴tattered rags as the weight had torn them partly down. On a table in the
10218𓀴hall was a great bunch of keys, with a time-yellowed label on each. They
10219𓀴had been used several times, for on the table were several similar rents
10220𓀴in the blanket of dust, similar to that exposed when the Professor
10221𓀴lifted them. He turned to me and said:--
10222𓀴
10223𓀴“You know this place, Jonathan. You have copied maps of it, and you know
10224𓀴it at least more than we do. Which is the way to the chapel?” I had an
10225𓀴idea of its direction, though on my former visit I had not been able to
10226𓀴get admission to it; so I led the way, and after a few wrong turnings
10227𓀴found myself opposite a low, arched oaken door, ribbed with iron bands.
10228𓀴“This is the spot,” said the Professor as he turned his lamp on a small
10229𓀴map of the house, copied from the file of my original correspondence
10230𓀴regarding the purchase. With a little trouble we found the key on the
10231𓀴bunch and opened the door. We were prepared for some unpleasantness, for
10232𓀴as we were opening the door a faint, malodorous air seemed to exhale
10233𓀴through the gaps, but none of us ever expected such an odour as we
10234𓀴encountered. None of the others had met the Count at all at close
10235𓀴quarters, and when I had seen him he was either in the fasting stage of
10236𓀴his existence in his rooms or, when he was gloated with fresh blood, in
10237𓀴a ruined building open to the air; but here the place was small and
10238𓀴close, and the long disuse had made the air stagnant and foul. There was
10239𓀴an earthy smell, as of some dry miasma, which came through the fouler
10240𓀴air. But as to the odour itself, how shall I describe it? It was not
10241𓀴alone that it was composed of all the ills of mortality and with the
10242𓀴pungent, acrid smell of blood, but it seemed as though corruption had
10243𓀴become itself corrupt. Faugh! it sickens me to think of it. Every breath
10244𓀴exhaled by that monster seemed to have clung to the place and
10245𓀴intensified its loathsomeness.
10246𓀴
10247𓀴Under ordinary circumstances such a stench would have brought our
10248𓀴enterprise to an end; but this was no ordinary case, and the high and
10249𓀴terrible purpose in which we were involved gave us a strength which rose
10250𓀴above merely physical considerations. After the involuntary shrinking
10251𓀴consequent on the first nauseous whiff, we one and all set about our
10252𓀴work as though that loathsome place were a garden of roses.
10253𓀴
10254𓀴We made an accurate examination of the place, the Professor saying as we
10255𓀴began:--
10256𓀴
10257𓀴“The first thing is to see how many of the boxes are left; we must then
10258𓀴examine every hole and corner and cranny and see if we cannot get some
10259𓀴clue as to what has become of the rest.” A glance was sufficient to show
10260𓀴how many remained, for the great earth chests were bulky, and there was
10261𓀴no mistaking them.
10262𓀴
10263𓀴There were only twenty-nine left out of the fifty! Once I got a fright,
10264𓀴for, seeing Lord Godalming suddenly turn and look out of the vaulted
10265𓀴door into the dark passage beyond, I looked too, and for an instant my
10266𓀴heart stood still. Somewhere, looking out from the shadow, I seemed to
10267𓀴see the high lights of the Count’s evil face, the ridge of the nose, the
10268𓀴red eyes, the red lips, the awful pallor. It was only for a moment, for,
10269𓀴as Lord Godalming said, “I thought I saw a face, but it was only the
10270𓀴shadows,” and resumed his inquiry, I turned my lamp in the direction,
10271𓀴and stepped into the passage. There was no sign of any one; and as there
10272𓀴were no corners, no doors, no aperture of any kind, but only the solid
10273𓀴walls of the passage, there could be no hiding-place even for _him_. I
10274𓀴took it that fear had helped imagination, and said nothing.
10275𓀴
10276𓀴A few minutes later I saw Morris step suddenly back from a corner, which
10277𓀴he was examining. We all followed his movements with our eyes, for
10278𓀴undoubtedly some nervousness was growing on us, and we saw a whole mass
10279𓀴of phosphorescence, which twinkled like stars. We all instinctively drew
10280𓀴back. The whole place was becoming alive with rats.
10281𓀴
10282𓀴For a moment or two we stood appalled, all save Lord Godalming, who was
10283𓀴seemingly prepared for such an emergency. Rushing over to the great
10284𓀴iron-bound oaken door, which Dr. Seward had described from the outside,
10285𓀴and which I had seen myself, he turned the key in the lock, drew the
10286𓀴huge bolts, and swung the door open. Then, taking his little silver
10287𓀴whistle from his pocket, he blew a low, shrill call. It was answered
10288𓀴from behind Dr. Seward’s house by the yelping of dogs, and after about a
10289𓀴minute three terriers came dashing round the corner of the house.
10290𓀴Unconsciously we had all moved towards the door, and as we moved I
10291𓀴noticed that the dust had been much disturbed: the boxes which had been
10292𓀴taken out had been brought this way. But even in the minute that had
10293𓀴elapsed the number of the rats had vastly increased. They seemed to
10294𓀴swarm over the place all at once, till the lamplight, shining on their
10295𓀴moving dark bodies and glittering, baleful eyes, made the place look
10296𓀴like a bank of earth set with fireflies. The dogs dashed on, but at the
10297𓀴threshold suddenly stopped and snarled, and then, simultaneously lifting
10298𓀴their noses, began to howl in most lugubrious fashion. The rats were
10299𓀴multiplying in thousands, and we moved out.
10300𓀴
10301𓀴Lord Godalming lifted one of the dogs, and carrying him in, placed him
10302𓀴on the floor. The instant his feet touched the ground he seemed to
10303𓀴recover his courage, and rushed at his natural enemies. They fled before
10304𓀴him so fast that before he had shaken the life out of a score, the other
10305𓀴dogs, who had by now been lifted in the same manner, had but small prey
10306𓀴ere the whole mass had vanished.
10307𓀴
10308𓀴With their going it seemed as if some evil presence had departed, for
10309𓀴the dogs frisked about and barked merrily as they made sudden darts at
10310𓀴their prostrate foes, and turned them over and over and tossed them in
10311𓀴the air with vicious shakes. We all seemed to find our spirits rise.
10312𓀴Whether it was the purifying of the deadly atmosphere by the opening of
10313𓀴the chapel door, or the relief which we experienced by finding ourselves
10314𓀴in the open I know not; but most certainly the shadow of dread seemed to
10315𓀴slip from us like a robe, and the occasion of our coming lost something
10316𓀴of its grim significance, though we did not slacken a whit in our
10317𓀴resolution. We closed the outer door and barred and locked it, and
10318𓀴bringing the dogs with us, began our search of the house. We found
10319𓀴nothing throughout except dust in extraordinary proportions, and all
10320𓀴untouched save for my own footsteps when I had made my first visit.
10321𓀴Never once did the dogs exhibit any symptom of uneasiness, and even when
10322𓀴we returned to the chapel they frisked about as though they had been
10323𓀴rabbit-hunting in a summer wood.
10324𓀴
10325𓀴The morning was quickening in the east when we emerged from the front.
10326𓀴Dr. Van Helsing had taken the key of the hall-door from the bunch, and
10327𓀴locked the door in orthodox fashion, putting the key into his pocket
10328𓀴when he had done.
10329𓀴
10330𓀴“So far,” he said, “our night has been eminently successful. No harm has
10331𓀴come to us such as I feared might be and yet we have ascertained how
10332𓀴many boxes are missing. More than all do I rejoice that this, our
10333𓀴first--and perhaps our most difficult and dangerous--step has been
10334𓀴accomplished without the bringing thereinto our most sweet Madam Mina or
10335𓀴troubling her waking or sleeping thoughts with sights and sounds and
10336𓀴smells of horror which she might never forget. One lesson, too, we have
10337𓀴learned, if it be allowable to argue _a particulari_: that the brute
10338𓀴beasts which are to the Count’s command are yet themselves not amenable
10339𓀴to his spiritual power; for look, these rats that would come to his
10340𓀴call, just as from his castle top he summon the wolves to your going and
10341𓀴to that poor mother’s cry, though they come to him, they run pell-mell
10342𓀴from the so little dogs of my friend Arthur. We have other matters
10343𓀴before us, other dangers, other fears; and that monster--he has not used
10344𓀴his power over the brute world for the only or the last time to-night.
10345𓀴So be it that he has gone elsewhere. Good! It has given us opportunity
10346𓀴to cry ‘check’ in some ways in this chess game, which we play for the
10347𓀴stake of human souls. And now let us go home. The dawn is close at hand,
10348𓀴and we have reason to be content with our first night’s work. It may be
10349𓀴ordained that we have many nights and days to follow, if full of peril;
10350𓀴but we must go on, and from no danger shall we shrink.”
10351𓀴
10352𓀴The house was silent when we got back, save for some poor creature who
10353𓀴was screaming away in one of the distant wards, and a low, moaning sound
10354𓀴from Renfield’s room. The poor wretch was doubtless torturing himself,
10355𓀴after the manner of the insane, with needless thoughts of pain.
10356𓀴
10357𓀴I came tiptoe into our own room, and found Mina asleep, breathing so
10358𓀴softly that I had to put my ear down to hear it. She looks paler than
10359𓀴usual. I hope the meeting to-night has not upset her. I am truly
10360𓀴thankful that she is to be left out of our future work, and even of our
10361𓀴deliberations. It is too great a strain for a woman to bear. I did not
10362𓀴think so at first, but I know better now. Therefore I am glad that it is
10363𓀴settled. There may be things which would frighten her to hear; and yet
10364𓀴to conceal them from her might be worse than to tell her if once she
10365𓀴suspected that there was any concealment. Henceforth our work is to be a
10366𓀴sealed book to her, till at least such time as we can tell her that all
10367𓀴is finished, and the earth free from a monster of the nether world. I
10368𓀴daresay it will be difficult to begin to keep silence after such
10369𓀴confidence as ours; but I must be resolute, and to-morrow I shall keep
10370𓀴dark over to-night’s doings, and shall refuse to speak of anything that
10371𓀴has happened. I rest on the sofa, so as not to disturb her.
10372𓀴
10373𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
10374𓀴
10375𓀴_1 October, later._--I suppose it was natural that we should have all
10376𓀴overslept ourselves, for the day was a busy one, and the night had no
10377𓀴rest at all. Even Mina must have felt its exhaustion, for though I slept
10378𓀴till the sun was high, I was awake before her, and had to call two or
10379𓀴three times before she awoke. Indeed, she was so sound asleep that for a
10380𓀴few seconds she did not recognize me, but looked at me with a sort of
10381𓀴blank terror, as one looks who has been waked out of a bad dream. She
10382𓀴complained a little of being tired, and I let her rest till later in the
10383𓀴day. We now know of twenty-one boxes having been removed, and if it be
10384𓀴that several were taken in any of these removals we may be able to trace
10385𓀴them all. Such will, of course, immensely simplify our labour, and the
10386𓀴sooner the matter is attended to the better. I shall look up Thomas
10387𓀴Snelling to-day.
10388𓀴
10389𓀴
10390𓀴_Dr. Seward’s Diary._
10391𓀴
10392𓀴_1 October._--It was towards noon when I was awakened by the Professor
10393𓀴walking into my room. He was more jolly and cheerful than usual, and it
10394𓀴is quite evident that last night’s work has helped to take some of the
10395𓀴brooding weight off his mind. After going over the adventure of the
10396𓀴night he suddenly said:--
10397𓀴
10398𓀴“Your patient interests me much. May it be that with you I visit him
10399𓀴this morning? Or if that you are too occupy, I can go alone if it may
10400𓀴be. It is a new experience to me to find a lunatic who talk philosophy,
10401𓀴and reason so sound.” I had some work to do which pressed, so I told him
10402𓀴that if he would go alone I would be glad, as then I should not have to
10403𓀴keep him waiting; so I called an attendant and gave him the necessary
10404𓀴instructions. Before the Professor left the room I cautioned him against
10405𓀴getting any false impression from my patient. “But,” he answered, “I
10406𓀴want him to talk of himself and of his delusion as to consuming live
10407𓀴things. He said to Madam Mina, as I see in your diary of yesterday, that
10408𓀴he had once had such a belief. Why do you smile, friend John?”
10409𓀴
10410𓀴“Excuse me,” I said, “but the answer is here.” I laid my hand on the
10411𓀴type-written matter. “When our sane and learned lunatic made that very
10412𓀴statement of how he _used_ to consume life, his mouth was actually
10413𓀴nauseous with the flies and spiders which he had eaten just before Mrs.
10414𓀴Harker entered the room.” Van Helsing smiled in turn. “Good!” he said.
10415𓀴“Your memory is true, friend John. I should have remembered. And yet it
10416𓀴is this very obliquity of thought and memory which makes mental disease
10417𓀴such a fascinating study. Perhaps I may gain more knowledge out of the
10418𓀴folly of this madman than I shall from the teaching of the most wise.
10419𓀴Who knows?” I went on with my work, and before long was through that in
10420𓀴hand. It seemed that the time had been very short indeed, but there was
10421𓀴Van Helsing back in the study. “Do I interrupt?” he asked politely as he
10422𓀴stood at the door.
10423𓀴
10424𓀴“Not at all,” I answered. “Come in. My work is finished, and I am free.
10425𓀴I can go with you now, if you like.
10426𓀴
10427𓀴“It is needless; I have seen him!”
10428𓀴
10429𓀴“Well?”
10430𓀴
10431𓀴“I fear that he does not appraise me at much. Our interview was short.
10432𓀴When I entered his room he was sitting on a stool in the centre, with
10433𓀴his elbows on his knees, and his face was the picture of sullen
10434𓀴discontent. I spoke to him as cheerfully as I could, and with such a
10435𓀴measure of respect as I could assume. He made no reply whatever. “Don’t
10436𓀴you know me?” I asked. His answer was not reassuring: “I know you well
10437𓀴enough; you are the old fool Van Helsing. I wish you would take yourself
10438𓀴and your idiotic brain theories somewhere else. Damn all thick-headed
10439𓀴Dutchmen!” Not a word more would he say, but sat in his implacable
10440𓀴sullenness as indifferent to me as though I had not been in the room at
10441𓀴all. Thus departed for this time my chance of much learning from this so
10442𓀴clever lunatic; so I shall go, if I may, and cheer myself with a few
10443𓀴happy words with that sweet soul Madam Mina. Friend John, it does
10444𓀴rejoice me unspeakable that she is no more to be pained, no more to be
10445𓀴worried with our terrible things. Though we shall much miss her help, it
10446𓀴is better so.”
10447𓀴
10448𓀴“I agree with you with all my heart,” I answered earnestly, for I did
10449𓀴not want him to weaken in this matter. “Mrs. Harker is better out of it.
10450𓀴Things are quite bad enough for us, all men of the world, and who have
10451𓀴been in many tight places in our time; but it is no place for a woman,
10452𓀴and if she had remained in touch with the affair, it would in time
10453𓀴infallibly have wrecked her.”
10454𓀴
10455𓀴So Van Helsing has gone to confer with Mrs. Harker and Harker; Quincey
10456𓀴and Art are all out following up the clues as to the earth-boxes. I
10457𓀴shall finish my round of work and we shall meet to-night.
10458𓀴
10459𓀴
10460𓀴_Mina Harker’s Journal._
10461𓀴
10462𓀴_1 October._--It is strange to me to be kept in the dark as I am to-day;
10463𓀴after Jonathan’s full confidence for so many years, to see him
10464𓀴manifestly avoid certain matters, and those the most vital of all. This
10465𓀴morning I slept late after the fatigues of yesterday, and though
10466𓀴Jonathan was late too, he was the earlier. He spoke to me before he went
10467𓀴out, never more sweetly or tenderly, but he never mentioned a word of
10468𓀴what had happened in the visit to the Count’s house. And yet he must
10469𓀴have known how terribly anxious I was. Poor dear fellow! I suppose it
10470𓀴must have distressed him even more than it did me. They all agreed that
10471𓀴it was best that I should not be drawn further into this awful work, and
10472𓀴I acquiesced. But to think that he keeps anything from me! And now I am
10473𓀴crying like a silly fool, when I _know_ it comes from my husband’s great
10474𓀴love and from the good, good wishes of those other strong men.
10475𓀴
10476𓀴That has done me good. Well, some day Jonathan will tell me all; and
10477𓀴lest it should ever be that he should think for a moment that I kept
10478𓀴anything from him, I still keep my journal as usual. Then if he has
10479𓀴feared of my trust I shall show it to him, with every thought of my
10480𓀴heart put down for his dear eyes to read. I feel strangely sad and
10481𓀴low-spirited to-day. I suppose it is the reaction from the terrible
10482𓀴excitement.
10483𓀴
10484𓀴Last night I went to bed when the men had gone, simply because they told
10485𓀴me to. I didn’t feel sleepy, and I did feel full of devouring anxiety. I
10486𓀴kept thinking over everything that has been ever since Jonathan came to
10487𓀴see me in London, and it all seems like a horrible tragedy, with fate
10488𓀴pressing on relentlessly to some destined end. Everything that one does
10489𓀴seems, no matter how right it may be, to bring on the very thing which
10490𓀴is most to be deplored. If I hadn’t gone to Whitby, perhaps poor dear
10491𓀴Lucy would be with us now. She hadn’t taken to visiting the churchyard
10492𓀴till I came, and if she hadn’t come there in the day-time with me she
10493𓀴wouldn’t have walked there in her sleep; and if she hadn’t gone there at
10494𓀴night and asleep, that monster couldn’t have destroyed her as he did.
10495𓀴Oh, why did I ever go to Whitby? There now, crying again! I wonder what
10496𓀴has come over me to-day. I must hide it from Jonathan, for if he knew
10497𓀴that I had been crying twice in one morning--I, who never cried on my
10498𓀴own account, and whom he has never caused to shed a tear--the dear
10499𓀴fellow would fret his heart out. I shall put a bold face on, and if I do
10500𓀴feel weepy, he shall never see it. I suppose it is one of the lessons
10501𓀴that we poor women have to learn....
10502𓀴
10503𓀴I can’t quite remember how I fell asleep last night. I remember hearing
10504𓀴the sudden barking of the dogs and a lot of queer sounds, like praying
10505𓀴on a very tumultuous scale, from Mr. Renfield’s room, which is somewhere
10506𓀴under this. And then there was silence over everything, silence so
10507𓀴profound that it startled me, and I got up and looked out of the window.
10508𓀴All was dark and silent, the black shadows thrown by the moonlight
10509𓀴seeming full of a silent mystery of their own. Not a thing seemed to be
10510𓀴stirring, but all to be grim and fixed as death or fate; so that a thin
10511𓀴streak of white mist, that crept with almost imperceptible slowness
10512𓀴across the grass towards the house, seemed to have a sentience and a
10513𓀴vitality of its own. I think that the digression of my thoughts must
10514𓀴have done me good, for when I got back to bed I found a lethargy
10515𓀴creeping over me. I lay a while, but could not quite sleep, so I got out
10516𓀴and looked out of the window again. The mist was spreading, and was now
10517𓀴close up to the house, so that I could see it lying thick against the
10518𓀴wall, as though it were stealing up to the windows. The poor man was
10519𓀴more loud than ever, and though I could not distinguish a word he said,
10520𓀴I could in some way recognise in his tones some passionate entreaty on
10521𓀴his part. Then there was the sound of a struggle, and I knew that the
10522𓀴attendants were dealing with him. I was so frightened that I crept into
10523𓀴bed, and pulled the clothes over my head, putting my fingers in my ears.
10524𓀴I was not then a bit sleepy, at least so I thought; but I must have
10525𓀴fallen asleep, for, except dreams, I do not remember anything until the
10526𓀴morning, when Jonathan woke me. I think that it took me an effort and a
10527𓀴little time to realise where I was, and that it was Jonathan who was
10528𓀴bending over me. My dream was very peculiar, and was almost typical of
10529𓀴the way that waking thoughts become merged in, or continued in, dreams.
10530𓀴
10531𓀴I thought that I was asleep, and waiting for Jonathan to come back. I
10532𓀴was very anxious about him, and I was powerless to act; my feet, and my
10533𓀴hands, and my brain were weighted, so that nothing could proceed at the
10534𓀴usual pace. And so I slept uneasily and thought. Then it began to dawn
10535𓀴upon me that the air was heavy, and dank, and cold. I put back the
10536𓀴clothes from my face, and found, to my surprise, that all was dim
10537𓀴around. The gaslight which I had left lit for Jonathan, but turned down,
10538𓀴came only like a tiny red spark through the fog, which had evidently
10539𓀴grown thicker and poured into the room. Then it occurred to me that I
10540𓀴had shut the window before I had come to bed. I would have got out to
10541𓀴make certain on the point, but some leaden lethargy seemed to chain my
10542𓀴limbs and even my will. I lay still and endured; that was all. I closed
10543𓀴my eyes, but could still see through my eyelids. (It is wonderful what
10544𓀴tricks our dreams play us, and how conveniently we can imagine.) The
10545𓀴mist grew thicker and thicker and I could see now how it came in, for I
10546𓀴could see it like smoke--or with the white energy of boiling
10547𓀴water--pouring in, not through the window, but through the joinings of
10548𓀴the door. It got thicker and thicker, till it seemed as if it became
10549𓀴concentrated into a sort of pillar of cloud in the room, through the top
10550𓀴of which I could see the light of the gas shining like a red eye. Things
10551𓀴began to whirl through my brain just as the cloudy column was now
10552𓀴whirling in the room, and through it all came the scriptural words “a
10553𓀴pillar of cloud by day and of fire by night.” Was it indeed some such
10554𓀴spiritual guidance that was coming to me in my sleep? But the pillar was
10555𓀴composed of both the day and the night-guiding, for the fire was in the
10556𓀴red eye, which at the thought got a new fascination for me; till, as I
10557𓀴looked, the fire divided, and seemed to shine on me through the fog like
10558𓀴two red eyes, such as Lucy told me of in her momentary mental wandering
10559𓀴when, on the cliff, the dying sunlight struck the windows of St. Mary’s
10560𓀴Church. Suddenly the horror burst upon me that it was thus that Jonathan
10561𓀴had seen those awful women growing into reality through the whirling mist
10562𓀴in the moonlight, and in my dream I must have fainted, for all became
10563𓀴black darkness. The last conscious effort which imagination made was to
10564𓀴show me a livid white face bending over me out of the mist. I must be
10565𓀴careful of such dreams, for they would unseat one’s reason if there were
10566𓀴too much of them. I would get Dr. Van Helsing or Dr. Seward to prescribe
10567𓀴something for me which would make me sleep, only that I fear to alarm
10568𓀴them. Such a dream at the present time would become woven into their
10569𓀴fears for me. To-night I shall strive hard to sleep naturally. If I do
10570𓀴not, I shall to-morrow night get them to give me a dose of chloral; that
10571𓀴cannot hurt me for once, and it will give me a good night’s sleep. Last
10572𓀴night tired me more than if I had not slept at all.
10573𓀴
10574𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
10575𓀴
10576𓀴_2 October 10 p. m._--Last night I slept, but did not dream. I must have
10577𓀴slept soundly, for I was not waked by Jonathan coming to bed; but the
10578𓀴sleep has not refreshed me, for to-day I feel terribly weak and
10579𓀴spiritless. I spent all yesterday trying to read, or lying down dozing.
10580𓀴In the afternoon Mr. Renfield asked if he might see me. Poor man, he was
10581𓀴very gentle, and when I came away he kissed my hand and bade God bless
10582𓀴me. Some way it affected me much; I am crying when I think of him. This
10583𓀴is a new weakness, of which I must be careful. Jonathan would be
10584𓀴miserable if he knew I had been crying. He and the others were out till
10585𓀴dinner-time, and they all came in tired. I did what I could to brighten
10586𓀴them up, and I suppose that the effort did me good, for I forgot how
10587𓀴tired I was. After dinner they sent me to bed, and all went off to smoke
10588𓀴together, as they said, but I knew that they wanted to tell each other
10589𓀴of what had occurred to each during the day; I could see from Jonathan’s
10590𓀴manner that he had something important to communicate. I was not so
10591𓀴sleepy as I should have been; so before they went I asked Dr. Seward to
10592𓀴give me a little opiate of some kind, as I had not slept well the night
10593𓀴before. He very kindly made me up a sleeping draught, which he gave to
10594𓀴me, telling me that it would do me no harm, as it was very mild.... I
10595𓀴have taken it, and am waiting for sleep, which still keeps aloof. I hope
10596𓀴I have not done wrong, for as sleep begins to flirt with me, a new fear
10597𓀴comes: that I may have been foolish in thus depriving myself of the
10598𓀴power of waking. I might want it. Here comes sleep. Good-night.
10599𓀴
10600𓀴
10601𓀴
10602𓀴
10603𓀴CHAPTER XX
10604𓀴
10605𓀴JONATHAN HARKER’S JOURNAL
10606𓀴
10607𓀴
10608𓀴_1 October, evening._--I found Thomas Snelling in his house at Bethnal
10609𓀴Green, but unhappily he was not in a condition to remember anything. The
10610𓀴very prospect of beer which my expected coming had opened to him had
10611𓀴proved too much, and he had begun too early on his expected debauch. I
10612𓀴learned, however, from his wife, who seemed a decent, poor soul, that he
10613𓀴was only the assistant to Smollet, who of the two mates was the
10614𓀴responsible person. So off I drove to Walworth, and found Mr. Joseph
10615𓀴Smollet at home and in his shirtsleeves, taking a late tea out of a
10616𓀴saucer. He is a decent, intelligent fellow, distinctly a good, reliable
10617𓀴type of workman, and with a headpiece of his own. He remembered all
10618𓀴about the incident of the boxes, and from a wonderful dog’s-eared
10619𓀴notebook, which he produced from some mysterious receptacle about the
10620𓀴seat of his trousers, and which had hieroglyphical entries in thick,
10621𓀴half-obliterated pencil, he gave me the destinations of the boxes. There
10622𓀴were, he said, six in the cartload which he took from Carfax and left at
10623𓀴197, Chicksand Street, Mile End New Town, and another six which he
10624𓀴deposited at Jamaica Lane, Bermondsey. If then the Count meant to
10625𓀴scatter these ghastly refuges of his over London, these places were
10626𓀴chosen as the first of delivery, so that later he might distribute more
10627𓀴fully. The systematic manner in which this was done made me think that
10628𓀴he could not mean to confine himself to two sides of London. He was now
10629𓀴fixed on the far east of the northern shore, on the east of the southern
10630𓀴shore, and on the south. The north and west were surely never meant to
10631𓀴be left out of his diabolical scheme--let alone the City itself and the
10632𓀴very heart of fashionable London in the south-west and west. I went back
10633𓀴to Smollet, and asked him if he could tell us if any other boxes had
10634𓀴been taken from Carfax.
10635𓀴
10636𓀴He replied:--
10637𓀴
10638𓀴“Well, guv’nor, you’ve treated me wery ’an’some”--I had given him half a
10639𓀴sovereign--“an’ I’ll tell yer all I know. I heard a man by the name of
10640𓀴Bloxam say four nights ago in the ’Are an’ ’Ounds, in Pincher’s Alley,
10641𓀴as ’ow he an’ his mate ’ad ’ad a rare dusty job in a old ’ouse at
10642𓀴Purfect. There ain’t a-many such jobs as this ’ere, an’ I’m thinkin’
10643𓀴that maybe Sam Bloxam could tell ye summut.” I asked if he could tell me
10644𓀴where to find him. I told him that if he could get me the address it
10645𓀴would be worth another half-sovereign to him. So he gulped down the rest
10646𓀴of his tea and stood up, saying that he was going to begin the search
10647𓀴then and there. At the door he stopped, and said:--
10648𓀴
10649𓀴“Look ’ere, guv’nor, there ain’t no sense in me a-keepin’ you ’ere. I
10650𓀴may find Sam soon, or I mayn’t; but anyhow he ain’t like to be in a way
10651𓀴to tell ye much to-night. Sam is a rare one when he starts on the booze.
10652𓀴If you can give me a envelope with a stamp on it, and put yer address on
10653𓀴it, I’ll find out where Sam is to be found and post it ye to-night. But
10654𓀴ye’d better be up arter ’im soon in the mornin’, or maybe ye won’t ketch
10655𓀴’im; for Sam gets off main early, never mind the booze the night afore.”
10656𓀴
10657𓀴This was all practical, so one of the children went off with a penny to
10658𓀴buy an envelope and a sheet of paper, and to keep the change. When she
10659𓀴came back, I addressed the envelope and stamped it, and when Smollet had
10660𓀴again faithfully promised to post the address when found, I took my way
10661𓀴to home. We’re on the track anyhow. I am tired to-night, and want sleep.
10662𓀴Mina is fast asleep, and looks a little too pale; her eyes look as
10663𓀴though she had been crying. Poor dear, I’ve no doubt it frets her to be
10664𓀴kept in the dark, and it may make her doubly anxious about me and the
10665𓀴others. But it is best as it is. It is better to be disappointed and
10666𓀴worried in such a way now than to have her nerve broken. The doctors
10667𓀴were quite right to insist on her being kept out of this dreadful
10668𓀴business. I must be firm, for on me this particular burden of silence
10669𓀴must rest. I shall not ever enter on the subject with her under any
10670𓀴circumstances. Indeed, it may not be a hard task, after all, for she
10671𓀴herself has become reticent on the subject, and has not spoken of the
10672𓀴Count or his doings ever since we told her of our decision.
10673𓀴
10674𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
10675𓀴
10676𓀴_2 October, evening._--A long and trying and exciting day. By the first
10677𓀴post I got my directed envelope with a dirty scrap of paper enclosed, on
10678𓀴which was written with a carpenter’s pencil in a sprawling hand:--
10679𓀴
10680𓀴“Sam Bloxam, Korkrans, 4, Poters Cort, Bartel Street, Walworth. Arsk for
10681𓀴the depite.”
10682𓀴
10683𓀴I got the letter in bed, and rose without waking Mina. She looked heavy
10684𓀴and sleepy and pale, and far from well. I determined not to wake her,
10685𓀴but that, when I should return from this new search, I would arrange for
10686𓀴her going back to Exeter. I think she would be happier in our own home,
10687𓀴with her daily tasks to interest her, than in being here amongst us and
10688𓀴in ignorance. I only saw Dr. Seward for a moment, and told him where I
10689𓀴was off to, promising to come back and tell the rest so soon as I should
10690𓀴have found out anything. I drove to Walworth and found, with some
10691𓀴difficulty, Potter’s Court. Mr. Smollet’s spelling misled me, as I asked
10692𓀴for Poter’s Court instead of Potter’s Court. However, when I had found
10693𓀴the court, I had no difficulty in discovering Corcoran’s lodging-house.
10694𓀴When I asked the man who came to the door for the “depite,” he shook his
10695𓀴head, and said: “I dunno ’im. There ain’t no such a person ’ere; I never
10696𓀴’eard of ’im in all my bloomin’ days. Don’t believe there ain’t nobody
10697𓀴of that kind livin’ ere or anywheres.” I took out Smollet’s letter, and
10698𓀴as I read it it seemed to me that the lesson of the spelling of the name
10699𓀴of the court might guide me. “What are you?” I asked.
10700𓀴
10701𓀴“I’m the depity,” he answered. I saw at once that I was on the right
10702𓀴track; phonetic spelling had again misled me. A half-crown tip put the
10703𓀴deputy’s knowledge at my disposal, and I learned that Mr. Bloxam, who
10704𓀴had slept off the remains of his beer on the previous night at
10705𓀴Corcoran’s, had left for his work at Poplar at five o’clock that
10706𓀴morning. He could not tell me where the place of work was situated, but
10707𓀴he had a vague idea that it was some kind of a “new-fangled ware’us”;
10708𓀴and with this slender clue I had to start for Poplar. It was twelve
10709𓀴o’clock before I got any satisfactory hint of such a building, and this
10710𓀴I got at a coffee-shop, where some workmen were having their dinner. One
10711𓀴of these suggested that there was being erected at Cross Angel Street a
10712𓀴new “cold storage” building; and as this suited the condition of a
10713𓀴“new-fangled ware’us,” I at once drove to it. An interview with a surly
10714𓀴gatekeeper and a surlier foreman, both of whom were appeased with the
10715𓀴coin of the realm, put me on the track of Bloxam; he was sent for on my
10716𓀴suggesting that I was willing to pay his day’s wages to his foreman for
10717𓀴the privilege of asking him a few questions on a private matter. He was
10718𓀴a smart enough fellow, though rough of speech and bearing. When I had
10719𓀴promised to pay for his information and given him an earnest, he told me
10720𓀴that he had made two journeys between Carfax and a house in Piccadilly,
10721𓀴and had taken from this house to the latter nine great boxes--“main
10722𓀴heavy ones”--with a horse and cart hired by him for this purpose. I
10723𓀴asked him if he could tell me the number of the house in Piccadilly, to
10724𓀴which he replied:--
10725𓀴
10726𓀴“Well, guv’nor, I forgits the number, but it was only a few doors from a
10727𓀴big white church or somethink of the kind, not long built. It was a
10728𓀴dusty old ’ouse, too, though nothin’ to the dustiness of the ’ouse we
10729𓀴tooked the bloomin’ boxes from.”
10730𓀴
10731𓀴“How did you get into the houses if they were both empty?”
10732𓀴
10733𓀴“There was the old party what engaged me a-waitin’ in the ’ouse at
10734𓀴Purfleet. He ’elped me to lift the boxes and put them in the dray. Curse
10735𓀴me, but he was the strongest chap I ever struck, an’ him a old feller,
10736𓀴with a white moustache, one that thin you would think he couldn’t throw
10737𓀴a shadder.”
10738𓀴
10739𓀴How this phrase thrilled through me!
10740𓀴
10741𓀴“Why, ’e took up ’is end o’ the boxes like they was pounds of tea, and
10742𓀴me a-puffin’ an’ a-blowin’ afore I could up-end mine anyhow--an’ I’m no
10743𓀴chicken, neither.”
10744𓀴
10745𓀴“How did you get into the house in Piccadilly?” I asked.
10746𓀴
10747𓀴“He was there too. He must ’a’ started off and got there afore me, for
10748𓀴when I rung of the bell he kem an’ opened the door ’isself an’ ’elped me
10749𓀴to carry the boxes into the ’all.”
10750𓀴
10751𓀴“The whole nine?” I asked.
10752𓀴
10753𓀴“Yus; there was five in the first load an’ four in the second. It was
10754𓀴main dry work, an’ I don’t so well remember ’ow I got ’ome.” I
10755𓀴interrupted him:--
10756𓀴
10757𓀴“Were the boxes left in the hall?”
10758𓀴
10759𓀴“Yus; it was a big ’all, an’ there was nothin’ else in it.” I made one
10760𓀴more attempt to further matters:--
10761𓀴
10762𓀴“You didn’t have any key?”
10763𓀴
10764𓀴“Never used no key nor nothink. The old gent, he opened the door ’isself
10765𓀴an’ shut it again when I druv off. I don’t remember the last time--but
10766𓀴that was the beer.”
10767𓀴
10768𓀴“And you can’t remember the number of the house?”
10769𓀴
10770𓀴“No, sir. But ye needn’t have no difficulty about that. It’s a ’igh ’un
10771𓀴with a stone front with a bow on it, an’ ’igh steps up to the door. I
10772𓀴know them steps, ’avin’ ’ad to carry the boxes up with three loafers
10773𓀴what come round to earn a copper. The old gent give them shillin’s, an’
10774𓀴they seein’ they got so much, they wanted more; but ’e took one of them
10775𓀴by the shoulder and was like to throw ’im down the steps, till the lot
10776𓀴of them went away cussin’.” I thought that with this description I could
10777𓀴find the house, so, having paid my friend for his information, I started
10778𓀴off for Piccadilly. I had gained a new painful experience; the Count
10779𓀴could, it was evident, handle the earth-boxes himself. If so, time was
10780𓀴precious; for, now that he had achieved a certain amount of
10781𓀴distribution, he could, by choosing his own time, complete the task
10782𓀴unobserved. At Piccadilly Circus I discharged my cab, and walked
10783𓀴westward; beyond the Junior Constitutional I came across the house
10784𓀴described, and was satisfied that this was the next of the lairs
10785𓀴arranged by Dracula. The house looked as though it had been long
10786𓀴untenanted. The windows were encrusted with dust, and the shutters were
10787𓀴up. All the framework was black with time, and from the iron the paint
10788𓀴had mostly scaled away. It was evident that up to lately there had been
10789𓀴a large notice-board in front of the balcony; it had, however, been
10790𓀴roughly torn away, the uprights which had supported it still remaining.
10791𓀴Behind the rails of the balcony I saw there were some loose boards,
10792𓀴whose raw edges looked white. I would have given a good deal to have
10793𓀴been able to see the notice-board intact, as it would, perhaps, have
10794𓀴given some clue to the ownership of the house. I remembered my
