10795𓀴experience of the investigation and purchase of Carfax, and I could not
10796𓀴but feel that if I could find the former owner there might be some means
10797𓀴discovered of gaining access to the house.
10798𓀴
10799𓀴There was at present nothing to be learned from the Piccadilly side, and
10800𓀴nothing could be done; so I went round to the back to see if anything
10801𓀴could be gathered from this quarter. The mews were active, the
10802𓀴Piccadilly houses being mostly in occupation. I asked one or two of the
10803𓀴grooms and helpers whom I saw around if they could tell me anything
10804𓀴about the empty house. One of them said that he heard it had lately been
10805𓀴taken, but he couldn’t say from whom. He told me, however, that up to
10806𓀴very lately there had been a notice-board of “For Sale” up, and that
10807𓀴perhaps Mitchell, Sons, & Candy, the house agents, could tell me
10808𓀴something, as he thought he remembered seeing the name of that firm on
10809𓀴the board. I did not wish to seem too eager, or to let my informant know
10810𓀴or guess too much, so, thanking him in the usual manner, I strolled
10811𓀴away. It was now growing dusk, and the autumn night was closing in, so I
10812𓀴did not lose any time. Having learned the address of Mitchell, Sons, &
10813𓀴Candy from a directory at the Berkeley, I was soon at their office in
10814𓀴Sackville Street.
10815𓀴
10816𓀴The gentleman who saw me was particularly suave in manner, but
10817𓀴uncommunicative in equal proportion. Having once told me that the
10818𓀴Piccadilly house--which throughout our interview he called a
10819𓀴“mansion”--was sold, he considered my business as concluded. When I
10820𓀴asked who had purchased it, he opened his eyes a thought wider, and
10821𓀴paused a few seconds before replying:--
10822𓀴
10823𓀴“It is sold, sir.”
10824𓀴
10825𓀴“Pardon me,” I said, with equal politeness, “but I have a special reason
10826𓀴for wishing to know who purchased it.”
10827𓀴
10828𓀴Again he paused longer, and raised his eyebrows still more. “It is sold,
10829𓀴sir,” was again his laconic reply.
10830𓀴
10831𓀴“Surely,” I said, “you do not mind letting me know so much.”
10832𓀴
10833𓀴“But I do mind,” he answered. “The affairs of their clients are
10834𓀴absolutely safe in the hands of Mitchell, Sons, & Candy.” This was
10835𓀴manifestly a prig of the first water, and there was no use arguing with
10836𓀴him. I thought I had best meet him on his own ground, so I said:--
10837𓀴
10838𓀴“Your clients, sir, are happy in having so resolute a guardian of their
10839𓀴confidence. I am myself a professional man.” Here I handed him my card.
10840𓀴“In this instance I am not prompted by curiosity; I act on the part of
10841𓀴Lord Godalming, who wishes to know something of the property which was,
10842𓀴he understood, lately for sale.” These words put a different complexion
10843𓀴on affairs. He said:--
10844𓀴
10845𓀴“I would like to oblige you if I could, Mr. Harker, and especially would
10846𓀴I like to oblige his lordship. We once carried out a small matter of
10847𓀴renting some chambers for him when he was the Honourable Arthur
10848𓀴Holmwood. If you will let me have his lordship’s address I will consult
10849𓀴the House on the subject, and will, in any case, communicate with his
10850𓀴lordship by to-night’s post. It will be a pleasure if we can so far
10851𓀴deviate from our rules as to give the required information to his
10852𓀴lordship.”
10853𓀴
10854𓀴I wanted to secure a friend, and not to make an enemy, so I thanked him,
10855𓀴gave the address at Dr. Seward’s and came away. It was now dark, and I
10856𓀴was tired and hungry. I got a cup of tea at the Aërated Bread Company
10857𓀴and came down to Purfleet by the next train.
10858𓀴
10859𓀴I found all the others at home. Mina was looking tired and pale, but she
10860𓀴made a gallant effort to be bright and cheerful, it wrung my heart to
10861𓀴think that I had had to keep anything from her and so caused her
10862𓀴inquietude. Thank God, this will be the last night of her looking on at
10863𓀴our conferences, and feeling the sting of our not showing our
10864𓀴confidence. It took all my courage to hold to the wise resolution of
10865𓀴keeping her out of our grim task. She seems somehow more reconciled; or
10866𓀴else the very subject seems to have become repugnant to her, for when
10867𓀴any accidental allusion is made she actually shudders. I am glad we
10868𓀴made our resolution in time, as with such a feeling as this, our growing
10869𓀴knowledge would be torture to her.
10870𓀴
10871𓀴I could not tell the others of the day’s discovery till we were alone;
10872𓀴so after dinner--followed by a little music to save appearances even
10873𓀴amongst ourselves--I took Mina to her room and left her to go to bed.
10874𓀴The dear girl was more affectionate with me than ever, and clung to me
10875𓀴as though she would detain me; but there was much to be talked of and I
10876𓀴came away. Thank God, the ceasing of telling things has made no
10877𓀴difference between us.
10878𓀴
10879𓀴When I came down again I found the others all gathered round the fire in
10880𓀴the study. In the train I had written my diary so far, and simply read
10881𓀴it off to them as the best means of letting them get abreast of my own
10882𓀴information; when I had finished Van Helsing said:--
10883𓀴
10884𓀴“This has been a great day’s work, friend Jonathan. Doubtless we are on
10885𓀴the track of the missing boxes. If we find them all in that house, then
10886𓀴our work is near the end. But if there be some missing, we must search
10887𓀴until we find them. Then shall we make our final _coup_, and hunt the
10888𓀴wretch to his real death.” We all sat silent awhile and all at once Mr.
10889𓀴Morris spoke:--
10890𓀴
10891𓀴“Say! how are we going to get into that house?”
10892𓀴
10893𓀴“We got into the other,” answered Lord Godalming quickly.
10894𓀴
10895𓀴“But, Art, this is different. We broke house at Carfax, but we had night
10896𓀴and a walled park to protect us. It will be a mighty different thing to
10897𓀴commit burglary in Piccadilly, either by day or night. I confess I don’t
10898𓀴see how we are going to get in unless that agency duck can find us a key
10899𓀴of some sort; perhaps we shall know when you get his letter in the
10900𓀴morning.” Lord Godalming’s brows contracted, and he stood up and walked
10901𓀴about the room. By-and-by he stopped and said, turning from one to
10902𓀴another of us:--
10903𓀴
10904𓀴“Quincey’s head is level. This burglary business is getting serious; we
10905𓀴got off once all right; but we have now a rare job on hand--unless we
10906𓀴can find the Count’s key basket.”
10907𓀴
10908𓀴As nothing could well be done before morning, and as it would be at
10909𓀴least advisable to wait till Lord Godalming should hear from Mitchell’s,
10910𓀴we decided not to take any active step before breakfast time. For a good
10911𓀴while we sat and smoked, discussing the matter in its various lights and
10912𓀴bearings; I took the opportunity of bringing this diary right up to the
10913𓀴moment. I am very sleepy and shall go to bed....
10914𓀴
10915𓀴Just a line. Mina sleeps soundly and her breathing is regular. Her
10916𓀴forehead is puckered up into little wrinkles, as though she thinks even
10917𓀴in her sleep. She is still too pale, but does not look so haggard as she
10918𓀴did this morning. To-morrow will, I hope, mend all this; she will be
10919𓀴herself at home in Exeter. Oh, but I am sleepy!
10920𓀴
10921𓀴
10922𓀴_Dr. Seward’s Diary._
10923𓀴
10924𓀴_1 October._--I am puzzled afresh about Renfield. His moods change so
10925𓀴rapidly that I find it difficult to keep touch of them, and as they
10926𓀴always mean something more than his own well-being, they form a more
10927𓀴than interesting study. This morning, when I went to see him after his
10928𓀴repulse of Van Helsing, his manner was that of a man commanding destiny.
10929𓀴He was, in fact, commanding destiny--subjectively. He did not really
10930𓀴care for any of the things of mere earth; he was in the clouds and
10931𓀴looked down on all the weaknesses and wants of us poor mortals. I
10932𓀴thought I would improve the occasion and learn something, so I asked
10933𓀴him:--
10934𓀴
10935𓀴“What about the flies these times?” He smiled on me in quite a superior
10936𓀴sort of way--such a smile as would have become the face of Malvolio--as
10937𓀴he answered me:--
10938𓀴
10939𓀴“The fly, my dear sir, has one striking feature; its wings are typical
10940𓀴of the aërial powers of the psychic faculties. The ancients did well
10941𓀴when they typified the soul as a butterfly!”
10942𓀴
10943𓀴I thought I would push his analogy to its utmost logically, so I said
10944𓀴quickly:--
10945𓀴
10946𓀴“Oh, it is a soul you are after now, is it?” His madness foiled his
10947𓀴reason, and a puzzled look spread over his face as, shaking his head
10948𓀴with a decision which I had but seldom seen in him, he said:--
10949𓀴
10950𓀴“Oh, no, oh no! I want no souls. Life is all I want.” Here he brightened
10951𓀴up; “I am pretty indifferent about it at present. Life is all right; I
10952𓀴have all I want. You must get a new patient, doctor, if you wish to
10953𓀴study zoöphagy!”
10954𓀴
10955𓀴This puzzled me a little, so I drew him on:--
10956𓀴
10957𓀴“Then you command life; you are a god, I suppose?” He smiled with an
10958𓀴ineffably benign superiority.
10959𓀴
10960𓀴“Oh no! Far be it from me to arrogate to myself the attributes of the
10961𓀴Deity. I am not even concerned in His especially spiritual doings. If I
10962𓀴may state my intellectual position I am, so far as concerns things
10963𓀴purely terrestrial, somewhat in the position which Enoch occupied
10964𓀴spiritually!” This was a poser to me. I could not at the moment recall
10965𓀴Enoch’s appositeness; so I had to ask a simple question, though I felt
10966𓀴that by so doing I was lowering myself in the eyes of the lunatic:--
10967𓀴
10968𓀴“And why with Enoch?”
10969𓀴
10970𓀴“Because he walked with God.” I could not see the analogy, but did not
10971𓀴like to admit it; so I harked back to what he had denied:--
10972𓀴
10973𓀴“So you don’t care about life and you don’t want souls. Why not?” I put
10974𓀴my question quickly and somewhat sternly, on purpose to disconcert him.
10975𓀴The effort succeeded; for an instant he unconsciously relapsed into his
10976𓀴old servile manner, bent low before me, and actually fawned upon me as
10977𓀴he replied:--
10978𓀴
10979𓀴“I don’t want any souls, indeed, indeed! I don’t. I couldn’t use them if
10980𓀴I had them; they would be no manner of use to me. I couldn’t eat them
10981𓀴or----” He suddenly stopped and the old cunning look spread over his
10982𓀴face, like a wind-sweep on the surface of the water. “And doctor, as to
10983𓀴life, what is it after all? When you’ve got all you require, and you
10984𓀴know that you will never want, that is all. I have friends--good
10985𓀴friends--like you, Dr. Seward”; this was said with a leer of
10986𓀴inexpressible cunning. “I know that I shall never lack the means of
10987𓀴life!”
10988𓀴
10989𓀴I think that through the cloudiness of his insanity he saw some
10990𓀴antagonism in me, for he at once fell back on the last refuge of such as
10991𓀴he--a dogged silence. After a short time I saw that for the present it
10992𓀴was useless to speak to him. He was sulky, and so I came away.
10993𓀴
10994𓀴Later in the day he sent for me. Ordinarily I would not have come
10995𓀴without special reason, but just at present I am so interested in him
10996𓀴that I would gladly make an effort. Besides, I am glad to have anything
10997𓀴to help to pass the time. Harker is out, following up clues; and so are
10998𓀴Lord Godalming and Quincey. Van Helsing sits in my study poring over the
10999𓀴record prepared by the Harkers; he seems to think that by accurate
11000𓀴knowledge of all details he will light upon some clue. He does not wish
11001𓀴to be disturbed in the work, without cause. I would have taken him with
11002𓀴me to see the patient, only I thought that after his last repulse he
11003𓀴might not care to go again. There was also another reason: Renfield
11004𓀴might not speak so freely before a third person as when he and I were
11005𓀴alone.
11006𓀴
11007𓀴I found him sitting out in the middle of the floor on his stool, a pose
11008𓀴which is generally indicative of some mental energy on his part. When I
11009𓀴came in, he said at once, as though the question had been waiting on his
11010𓀴lips:--
11011𓀴
11012𓀴“What about souls?” It was evident then that my surmise had been
11013𓀴correct. Unconscious cerebration was doing its work, even with the
11014𓀴lunatic. I determined to have the matter out. “What about them
11015𓀴yourself?” I asked. He did not reply for a moment but looked all round
11016𓀴him, and up and down, as though he expected to find some inspiration for
11017𓀴an answer.
11018𓀴
11019𓀴“I don’t want any souls!” he said in a feeble, apologetic way. The
11020𓀴matter seemed preying on his mind, and so I determined to use it--to “be
11021𓀴cruel only to be kind.” So I said:--
11022𓀴
11023𓀴“You like life, and you want life?”
11024𓀴
11025𓀴“Oh yes! but that is all right; you needn’t worry about that!”
11026𓀴
11027𓀴“But,” I asked, “how are we to get the life without getting the soul
11028𓀴also?” This seemed to puzzle him, so I followed it up:--
11029𓀴
11030𓀴“A nice time you’ll have some time when you’re flying out there, with
11031𓀴the souls of thousands of flies and spiders and birds and cats buzzing
11032𓀴and twittering and miauing all round you. You’ve got their lives, you
11033𓀴know, and you must put up with their souls!” Something seemed to affect
11034𓀴his imagination, for he put his fingers to his ears and shut his eyes,
11035𓀴screwing them up tightly just as a small boy does when his face is being
11036𓀴soaped. There was something pathetic in it that touched me; it also gave
11037𓀴me a lesson, for it seemed that before me was a child--only a child,
11038𓀴though the features were worn, and the stubble on the jaws was white. It
11039𓀴was evident that he was undergoing some process of mental disturbance,
11040𓀴and, knowing how his past moods had interpreted things seemingly foreign
11041𓀴to himself, I thought I would enter into his mind as well as I could and
11042𓀴go with him. The first step was to restore confidence, so I asked him,
11043𓀴speaking pretty loud so that he would hear me through his closed ears:--
11044𓀴
11045𓀴“Would you like some sugar to get your flies round again?” He seemed to
11046𓀴wake up all at once, and shook his head. With a laugh he replied:--
11047𓀴
11048𓀴“Not much! flies are poor things, after all!” After a pause he added,
11049𓀴“But I don’t want their souls buzzing round me, all the same.”
11050𓀴
11051𓀴“Or spiders?” I went on.
11052𓀴
11053𓀴“Blow spiders! What’s the use of spiders? There isn’t anything in them
11054𓀴to eat or”--he stopped suddenly, as though reminded of a forbidden
11055𓀴topic.
11056𓀴
11057𓀴“So, so!” I thought to myself, “this is the second time he has suddenly
11058𓀴stopped at the word ‘drink’; what does it mean?” Renfield seemed himself
11059𓀴aware of having made a lapse, for he hurried on, as though to distract
11060𓀴my attention from it:--
11061𓀴
11062𓀴“I don’t take any stock at all in such matters. ‘Rats and mice and such
11063𓀴small deer,’ as Shakespeare has it, ‘chicken-feed of the larder’ they
11064𓀴might be called. I’m past all that sort of nonsense. You might as well
11065𓀴ask a man to eat molecules with a pair of chop-sticks, as to try to
11066𓀴interest me about the lesser carnivora, when I know of what is before
11067𓀴me.”
11068𓀴
11069𓀴“I see,” I said. “You want big things that you can make your teeth meet
11070𓀴in? How would you like to breakfast on elephant?”
11071𓀴
11072𓀴“What ridiculous nonsense you are talking!” He was getting too wide
11073𓀴awake, so I thought I would press him hard. “I wonder,” I said
11074𓀴reflectively, “what an elephant’s soul is like!”
11075𓀴
11076𓀴The effect I desired was obtained, for he at once fell from his
11077𓀴high-horse and became a child again.
11078𓀴
11079𓀴“I don’t want an elephant’s soul, or any soul at all!” he said. For a
11080𓀴few moments he sat despondently. Suddenly he jumped to his feet, with
11081𓀴his eyes blazing and all the signs of intense cerebral excitement. “To
11082𓀴hell with you and your souls!” he shouted. “Why do you plague me about
11083𓀴souls? Haven’t I got enough to worry, and pain, and distract me already,
11084𓀴without thinking of souls!” He looked so hostile that I thought he was
11085𓀴in for another homicidal fit, so I blew my whistle. The instant,
11086𓀴however, that I did so he became calm, and said apologetically:--
11087𓀴
11088𓀴“Forgive me, Doctor; I forgot myself. You do not need any help. I am so
11089𓀴worried in my mind that I am apt to be irritable. If you only knew the
11090𓀴problem I have to face, and that I am working out, you would pity, and
11091𓀴tolerate, and pardon me. Pray do not put me in a strait-waistcoat. I
11092𓀴want to think and I cannot think freely when my body is confined. I am
11093𓀴sure you will understand!” He had evidently self-control; so when the
11094𓀴attendants came I told them not to mind, and they withdrew. Renfield
11095𓀴watched them go; when the door was closed he said, with considerable
11096𓀴dignity and sweetness:--
11097𓀴
11098𓀴“Dr. Seward, you have been very considerate towards me. Believe me that
11099𓀴I am very, very grateful to you!” I thought it well to leave him in this
11100𓀴mood, and so I came away. There is certainly something to ponder over in
11101𓀴this man’s state. Several points seem to make what the American
11102𓀴interviewer calls “a story,” if one could only get them in proper order.
11103𓀴Here they are:--
11104𓀴
11105𓀴Will not mention “drinking.”
11106𓀴
11107𓀴Fears the thought of being burdened with the “soul” of anything.
11108𓀴
11109𓀴Has no dread of wanting “life” in the future.
11110𓀴
11111𓀴Despises the meaner forms of life altogether, though he dreads being
11112𓀴haunted by their souls.
11113𓀴
11114𓀴Logically all these things point one way! he has assurance of some kind
11115𓀴that he will acquire some higher life. He dreads the consequence--the
11116𓀴burden of a soul. Then it is a human life he looks to!
11117𓀴
11118𓀴And the assurance--?
11119𓀴
11120𓀴Merciful God! the Count has been to him, and there is some new scheme of
11121𓀴terror afoot!
11122𓀴
11123𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
11124𓀴
11125𓀴_Later._--I went after my round to Van Helsing and told him my
11126𓀴suspicion. He grew very grave; and, after thinking the matter over for a
11127𓀴while asked me to take him to Renfield. I did so. As we came to the door
11128𓀴we heard the lunatic within singing gaily, as he used to do in the time
11129𓀴which now seems so long ago. When we entered we saw with amazement that
11130𓀴he had spread out his sugar as of old; the flies, lethargic with the
11131𓀴autumn, were beginning to buzz into the room. We tried to make him talk
11132𓀴of the subject of our previous conversation, but he would not attend. He
11133𓀴went on with his singing, just as though we had not been present. He had
11134𓀴got a scrap of paper and was folding it into a note-book. We had to come
11135𓀴away as ignorant as we went in.
11136𓀴
11137𓀴His is a curious case indeed; we must watch him to-night.
11138𓀴
11139𓀴
11140𓀴_Letter, Mitchell, Sons and Candy to Lord Godalming._
11141𓀴
11142𓀴_“1 October._
11143𓀴
11144𓀴“My Lord,
11145𓀴
11146𓀴“We are at all times only too happy to meet your wishes. We beg, with
11147𓀴regard to the desire of your Lordship, expressed by Mr. Harker on your
11148𓀴behalf, to supply the following information concerning the sale and
11149𓀴purchase of No. 347, Piccadilly. The original vendors are the executors
11150𓀴of the late Mr. Archibald Winter-Suffield. The purchaser is a foreign
11151𓀴nobleman, Count de Ville, who effected the purchase himself paying the
11152𓀴purchase money in notes ‘over the counter,’ if your Lordship will pardon
11153𓀴us using so vulgar an expression. Beyond this we know nothing whatever
11154𓀴of him.
11155𓀴
11156𓀴“We are, my Lord,
11157𓀴
11158𓀴“Your Lordship’s humble servants,
11159𓀴
11160𓀴“MITCHELL, SONS & CANDY.”
11161𓀴
11162𓀴
11163𓀴_Dr. Seward’s Diary._
11164𓀴
11165𓀴_2 October._--I placed a man in the corridor last night, and told him to
11166𓀴make an accurate note of any sound he might hear from Renfield’s room,
11167𓀴and gave him instructions that if there should be anything strange he
11168𓀴was to call me. After dinner, when we had all gathered round the fire
11169𓀴in the study--Mrs. Harker having gone to bed--we discussed the attempts
11170𓀴and discoveries of the day. Harker was the only one who had any result,
11171𓀴and we are in great hopes that his clue may be an important one.
11172𓀴
11173𓀴Before going to bed I went round to the patient’s room and looked in
11174𓀴through the observation trap. He was sleeping soundly, and his heart
11175𓀴rose and fell with regular respiration.
11176𓀴
11177𓀴This morning the man on duty reported to me that a little after midnight
11178𓀴he was restless and kept saying his prayers somewhat loudly. I asked him
11179𓀴if that was all; he replied that it was all he heard. There was
11180𓀴something about his manner so suspicious that I asked him point blank if
11181𓀴he had been asleep. He denied sleep, but admitted to having “dozed” for
11182𓀴a while. It is too bad that men cannot be trusted unless they are
11183𓀴watched.
11184𓀴
11185𓀴To-day Harker is out following up his clue, and Art and Quincey are
11186𓀴looking after horses. Godalming thinks that it will be well to have
11187𓀴horses always in readiness, for when we get the information which we
11188𓀴seek there will be no time to lose. We must sterilise all the imported
11189𓀴earth between sunrise and sunset; we shall thus catch the Count at his
11190𓀴weakest, and without a refuge to fly to. Van Helsing is off to the
11191𓀴British Museum looking up some authorities on ancient medicine. The old
11192𓀴physicians took account of things which their followers do not accept,
11193𓀴and the Professor is searching for witch and demon cures which may be
11194𓀴useful to us later.
11195𓀴
11196𓀴I sometimes think we must be all mad and that we shall wake to sanity in
11197𓀴strait-waistcoats.
11198𓀴
11199𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
11200𓀴
11201𓀴_Later._--We have met again. We seem at last to be on the track, and our
11202𓀴work of to-morrow may be the beginning of the end. I wonder if
11203𓀴Renfield’s quiet has anything to do with this. His moods have so
11204𓀴followed the doings of the Count, that the coming destruction of the
11205𓀴monster may be carried to him in some subtle way. If we could only get
11206𓀴some hint as to what passed in his mind, between the time of my argument
11207𓀴with him to-day and his resumption of fly-catching, it might afford us a
11208𓀴valuable clue. He is now seemingly quiet for a spell.... Is he?---- That
11209𓀴wild yell seemed to come from his room....
11210𓀴
11211𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
11212𓀴
11213𓀴The attendant came bursting into my room and told me that Renfield had
11214𓀴somehow met with some accident. He had heard him yell; and when he went
11215𓀴to him found him lying on his face on the floor, all covered with blood.
11216𓀴I must go at once....
11217𓀴
11218𓀴
11219𓀴
11220𓀴
11221𓀴CHAPTER XXI
11222𓀴
11223𓀴DR. SEWARD’S DIARY
11224𓀴
11225𓀴
11226𓀴_3 October._--Let me put down with exactness all that happened, as well
11227𓀴as I can remember it, since last I made an entry. Not a detail that I
11228𓀴can recall must be forgotten; in all calmness I must proceed.
11229𓀴
11230𓀴When I came to Renfield’s room I found him lying on the floor on his
11231𓀴left side in a glittering pool of blood. When I went to move him, it
11232𓀴became at once apparent that he had received some terrible injuries;
11233𓀴there seemed none of that unity of purpose between the parts of the body
11234𓀴which marks even lethargic sanity. As the face was exposed I could see
11235𓀴that it was horribly bruised, as though it had been beaten against the
11236𓀴floor--indeed it was from the face wounds that the pool of blood
11237𓀴originated. The attendant who was kneeling beside the body said to me as
11238𓀴we turned him over:--
11239𓀴
11240𓀴“I think, sir, his back is broken. See, both his right arm and leg and
11241𓀴the whole side of his face are paralysed.” How such a thing could have
11242𓀴happened puzzled the attendant beyond measure. He seemed quite
11243𓀴bewildered, and his brows were gathered in as he said:--
11244𓀴
11245𓀴“I can’t understand the two things. He could mark his face like that by
11246𓀴beating his own head on the floor. I saw a young woman do it once at the
11247𓀴Eversfield Asylum before anyone could lay hands on her. And I suppose he
11248𓀴might have broke his neck by falling out of bed, if he got in an awkward
11249𓀴kink. But for the life of me I can’t imagine how the two things
11250𓀴occurred. If his back was broke, he couldn’t beat his head; and if his
11251𓀴face was like that before the fall out of bed, there would be marks of
11252𓀴it.” I said to him:--
11253𓀴
11254𓀴“Go to Dr. Van Helsing, and ask him to kindly come here at once. I want
11255𓀴him without an instant’s delay.” The man ran off, and within a few
11256𓀴minutes the Professor, in his dressing gown and slippers, appeared. When
11257𓀴he saw Renfield on the ground, he looked keenly at him a moment, and
11258𓀴then turned to me. I think he recognised my thought in my eyes, for he
11259𓀴said very quietly, manifestly for the ears of the attendant:--
11260𓀴
11261𓀴“Ah, a sad accident! He will need very careful watching, and much
11262𓀴attention. I shall stay with you myself; but I shall first dress myself.
11263𓀴If you will remain I shall in a few minutes join you.”
11264𓀴
11265𓀴The patient was now breathing stertorously and it was easy to see that
11266𓀴he had suffered some terrible injury. Van Helsing returned with
11267𓀴extraordinary celerity, bearing with him a surgical case. He had
11268𓀴evidently been thinking and had his mind made up; for, almost before he
11269𓀴looked at the patient, he whispered to me:--
11270𓀴
11271𓀴“Send the attendant away. We must be alone with him when he becomes
11272𓀴conscious, after the operation.” So I said:--
11273𓀴
11274𓀴“I think that will do now, Simmons. We have done all that we can at
11275𓀴present. You had better go your round, and Dr. Van Helsing will operate.
11276𓀴Let me know instantly if there be anything unusual anywhere.”
11277𓀴
11278𓀴The man withdrew, and we went into a strict examination of the patient.
11279𓀴The wounds of the face was superficial; the real injury was a depressed
11280𓀴fracture of the skull, extending right up through the motor area. The
11281𓀴Professor thought a moment and said:--
11282𓀴
11283𓀴“We must reduce the pressure and get back to normal conditions, as far
11284𓀴as can be; the rapidity of the suffusion shows the terrible nature of
11285𓀴his injury. The whole motor area seems affected. The suffusion of the
11286𓀴brain will increase quickly, so we must trephine at once or it may be
11287𓀴too late.” As he was speaking there was a soft tapping at the door. I
11288𓀴went over and opened it and found in the corridor without, Arthur and
11289𓀴Quincey in pajamas and slippers: the former spoke:--
11290𓀴
11291𓀴“I heard your man call up Dr. Van Helsing and tell him of an accident.
11292𓀴So I woke Quincey or rather called for him as he was not asleep. Things
11293𓀴are moving too quickly and too strangely for sound sleep for any of us
11294𓀴these times. I’ve been thinking that to-morrow night will not see things
11295𓀴as they have been. We’ll have to look back--and forward a little more
11296𓀴than we have done. May we come in?” I nodded, and held the door open
11297𓀴till they had entered; then I closed it again. When Quincey saw the
11298𓀴attitude and state of the patient, and noted the horrible pool on the
11299𓀴floor, he said softly:--
11300𓀴
11301𓀴“My God! what has happened to him? Poor, poor devil!” I told him
11302𓀴briefly, and added that we expected he would recover consciousness after
11303𓀴the operation--for a short time, at all events. He went at once and sat
11304𓀴down on the edge of the bed, with Godalming beside him; we all watched
11305𓀴in patience.
11306𓀴
11307𓀴“We shall wait,” said Van Helsing, “just long enough to fix the best
11308𓀴spot for trephining, so that we may most quickly and perfectly remove
11309𓀴the blood clot; for it is evident that the hæmorrhage is increasing.”
11310𓀴
11311𓀴The minutes during which we waited passed with fearful slowness. I had a
11312𓀴horrible sinking in my heart, and from Van Helsing’s face I gathered
11313𓀴that he felt some fear or apprehension as to what was to come. I dreaded
11314𓀴the words that Renfield might speak. I was positively afraid to think;
11315𓀴but the conviction of what was coming was on me, as I have read of men
11316𓀴who have heard the death-watch. The poor man’s breathing came in
11317𓀴uncertain gasps. Each instant he seemed as though he would open his eyes
11318𓀴and speak; but then would follow a prolonged stertorous breath, and he
11319𓀴would relapse into a more fixed insensibility. Inured as I was to sick
11320𓀴beds and death, this suspense grew, and grew upon me. I could almost
11321𓀴hear the beating of my own heart; and the blood surging through my
11322𓀴temples sounded like blows from a hammer. The silence finally became
11323𓀴agonising. I looked at my companions, one after another, and saw from
11324𓀴their flushed faces and damp brows that they were enduring equal
11325𓀴torture. There was a nervous suspense over us all, as though overhead
11326𓀴some dread bell would peal out powerfully when we should least expect
11327𓀴it.
11328𓀴
11329𓀴At last there came a time when it was evident that the patient was
11330𓀴sinking fast; he might die at any moment. I looked up at the Professor
11331𓀴and caught his eyes fixed on mine. His face was sternly set as he
11332𓀴spoke:--
11333𓀴
11334𓀴“There is no time to lose. His words may be worth many lives; I have
11335𓀴been thinking so, as I stood here. It may be there is a soul at stake!
11336𓀴We shall operate just above the ear.”
11337𓀴
11338𓀴Without another word he made the operation. For a few moments the
11339𓀴breathing continued to be stertorous. Then there came a breath so
11340𓀴prolonged that it seemed as though it would tear open his chest.
11341𓀴Suddenly his eyes opened, and became fixed in a wild, helpless stare.
11342𓀴This was continued for a few moments; then it softened into a glad
11343𓀴surprise, and from the lips came a sigh of relief. He moved
11344𓀴convulsively, and as he did so, said:--
11345𓀴
11346𓀴“I’ll be quiet, Doctor. Tell them to take off the strait-waistcoat. I
11347𓀴have had a terrible dream, and it has left me so weak that I cannot
11348𓀴move. What’s wrong with my face? it feels all swollen, and it smarts
11349𓀴dreadfully.” He tried to turn his head; but even with the effort his
11350𓀴eyes seemed to grow glassy again so I gently put it back. Then Van
11351𓀴Helsing said in a quiet grave tone:--
11352𓀴
11353𓀴“Tell us your dream, Mr. Renfield.” As he heard the voice his face
11354𓀴brightened, through its mutilation, and he said:--
11355𓀴
11356𓀴“That is Dr. Van Helsing. How good it is of you to be here. Give me some
11357𓀴water, my lips are dry; and I shall try to tell you. I dreamed”--he
11358𓀴stopped and seemed fainting, I called quietly to Quincey--“The
11359𓀴brandy--it is in my study--quick!” He flew and returned with a glass,
11360𓀴the decanter of brandy and a carafe of water. We moistened the parched
11361𓀴lips, and the patient quickly revived. It seemed, however, that his poor
11362𓀴injured brain had been working in the interval, for, when he was quite
11363𓀴conscious, he looked at me piercingly with an agonised confusion which I
11364𓀴shall never forget, and said:--
11365𓀴
11366𓀴“I must not deceive myself; it was no dream, but all a grim reality.”
11367𓀴Then his eyes roved round the room; as they caught sight of the two
11368𓀴figures sitting patiently on the edge of the bed he went on:--
11369𓀴
11370𓀴“If I were not sure already, I would know from them.” For an instant his
11371𓀴eyes closed--not with pain or sleep but voluntarily, as though he were
11372𓀴bringing all his faculties to bear; when he opened them he said,
11373𓀴hurriedly, and with more energy than he had yet displayed:--
11374𓀴
11375𓀴“Quick, Doctor, quick. I am dying! I feel that I have but a few minutes;
11376𓀴and then I must go back to death--or worse! Wet my lips with brandy
11377𓀴again. I have something that I must say before I die; or before my poor
11378𓀴crushed brain dies anyhow. Thank you! It was that night after you left
11379𓀴me, when I implored you to let me go away. I couldn’t speak then, for I
11380𓀴felt my tongue was tied; but I was as sane then, except in that way, as
11381𓀴I am now. I was in an agony of despair for a long time after you left
11382𓀴me; it seemed hours. Then there came a sudden peace to me. My brain
11383𓀴seemed to become cool again, and I realised where I was. I heard the
11384𓀴dogs bark behind our house, but not where He was!” As he spoke, Van
11385𓀴Helsing’s eyes never blinked, but his hand came out and met mine and
11386𓀴gripped it hard. He did not, however, betray himself; he nodded slightly
11387𓀴and said: “Go on,” in a low voice. Renfield proceeded:--
11388𓀴
11389𓀴“He came up to the window in the mist, as I had seen him often before;
11390𓀴but he was solid then--not a ghost, and his eyes were fierce like a
11391𓀴man’s when angry. He was laughing with his red mouth; the sharp white
11392𓀴teeth glinted in the moonlight when he turned to look back over the belt
11393𓀴of trees, to where the dogs were barking. I wouldn’t ask him to come in
11394𓀴at first, though I knew he wanted to--just as he had wanted all along.
11395𓀴Then he began promising me things--not in words but by doing them.” He
11396𓀴was interrupted by a word from the Professor:--
11397𓀴
11398𓀴“How?”
11399𓀴
11400𓀴“By making them happen; just as he used to send in the flies when the
11401𓀴sun was shining. Great big fat ones with steel and sapphire on their
11402𓀴wings; and big moths, in the night, with skull and cross-bones on their
11403𓀴backs.” Van Helsing nodded to him as he whispered to me unconsciously:--
11404𓀴
11405𓀴“The _Acherontia Aitetropos of the Sphinges_--what you call the
11406𓀴‘Death’s-head Moth’?” The patient went on without stopping.
11407𓀴
11408𓀴“Then he began to whisper: ‘Rats, rats, rats! Hundreds, thousands,
11409𓀴millions of them, and every one a life; and dogs to eat them, and cats
11410𓀴too. All lives! all red blood, with years of life in it; and not merely
11411𓀴buzzing flies!’ I laughed at him, for I wanted to see what he could do.
11412𓀴Then the dogs howled, away beyond the dark trees in His house. He
11413𓀴beckoned me to the window. I got up and looked out, and He raised his
11414𓀴hands, and seemed to call out without using any words. A dark mass
11415𓀴spread over the grass, coming on like the shape of a flame of fire; and
11416𓀴then He moved the mist to the right and left, and I could see that there
11417𓀴were thousands of rats with their eyes blazing red--like His, only
11418𓀴smaller. He held up his hand, and they all stopped; and I thought he
11419𓀴seemed to be saying: ‘All these lives will I give you, ay, and many more
11420𓀴and greater, through countless ages, if you will fall down and worship
11421𓀴me!’ And then a red cloud, like the colour of blood, seemed to close
11422𓀴over my eyes; and before I knew what I was doing, I found myself opening
11423𓀴the sash and saying to Him: ‘Come in, Lord and Master!’ The rats were
11424𓀴all gone, but He slid into the room through the sash, though it was only
11425𓀴open an inch wide--just as the Moon herself has often come in through
11426𓀴the tiniest crack and has stood before me in all her size and
11427𓀴splendour.”
11428𓀴
11429𓀴His voice was weaker, so I moistened his lips with the brandy again, and
11430𓀴he continued; but it seemed as though his memory had gone on working in
11431𓀴the interval for his story was further advanced. I was about to call him
11432𓀴back to the point, but Van Helsing whispered to me: “Let him go on. Do
11433𓀴not interrupt him; he cannot go back, and maybe could not proceed at all
11434𓀴if once he lost the thread of his thought.” He proceeded:--
11435𓀴
11436𓀴“All day I waited to hear from him, but he did not send me anything, not
11437𓀴even a blow-fly, and when the moon got up I was pretty angry with him.
11438𓀴When he slid in through the window, though it was shut, and did not even
11439𓀴knock, I got mad with him. He sneered at me, and his white face looked
11440𓀴out of the mist with his red eyes gleaming, and he went on as though he
11441𓀴owned the whole place, and I was no one. He didn’t even smell the same
11442𓀴as he went by me. I couldn’t hold him. I thought that, somehow, Mrs.
11443𓀴Harker had come into the room.”
11444𓀴
11445𓀴The two men sitting on the bed stood up and came over, standing behind
11446𓀴him so that he could not see them, but where they could hear better.
11447𓀴They were both silent, but the Professor started and quivered; his face,
11448𓀴however, grew grimmer and sterner still. Renfield went on without
11449𓀴noticing:--
11450𓀴
11451𓀴“When Mrs. Harker came in to see me this afternoon she wasn’t the same;
11452𓀴it was like tea after the teapot had been watered.” Here we all moved,
11453𓀴but no one said a word; he went on:--
11454𓀴
11455𓀴“I didn’t know that she was here till she spoke; and she didn’t look the
11456𓀴same. I don’t care for the pale people; I like them with lots of blood
11457𓀴in them, and hers had all seemed to have run out. I didn’t think of it
11458𓀴at the time; but when she went away I began to think, and it made me mad
11459𓀴to know that He had been taking the life out of her.” I could feel that
11460𓀴the rest quivered, as I did, but we remained otherwise still. “So when
11461𓀴He came to-night I was ready for Him. I saw the mist stealing in, and I
11462𓀴grabbed it tight. I had heard that madmen have unnatural strength; and
11463𓀴as I knew I was a madman--at times anyhow--I resolved to use my power.
11464𓀴Ay, and He felt it too, for He had to come out of the mist to struggle
11465𓀴with me. I held tight; and I thought I was going to win, for I didn’t
11466𓀴mean Him to take any more of her life, till I saw His eyes. They burned
11467𓀴into me, and my strength became like water. He slipped through it, and
11468𓀴when I tried to cling to Him, He raised me up and flung me down. There
11469𓀴was a red cloud before me, and a noise like thunder, and the mist seemed
11470𓀴to steal away under the door.” His voice was becoming fainter and his
11471𓀴breath more stertorous. Van Helsing stood up instinctively.
11472𓀴
11473𓀴“We know the worst now,” he said. “He is here, and we know his purpose.
11474𓀴It may not be too late. Let us be armed--the same as we were the other
11475𓀴night, but lose no time; there is not an instant to spare.” There was no
11476𓀴need to put our fear, nay our conviction, into words--we shared them in
11477𓀴common. We all hurried and took from our rooms the same things that we
11478𓀴had when we entered the Count’s house. The Professor had his ready, and
11479𓀴as we met in the corridor he pointed to them significantly as he said:--
11480𓀴
11481𓀴“They never leave me; and they shall not till this unhappy business is
11482𓀴over. Be wise also, my friends. It is no common enemy that we deal with.
11483𓀴Alas! alas! that that dear Madam Mina should suffer!” He stopped; his
11484𓀴voice was breaking, and I do not know if rage or terror predominated in
11485𓀴my own heart.
11486𓀴
11487𓀴Outside the Harkers’ door we paused. Art and Quincey held back, and the
11488𓀴latter said:--
11489𓀴
11490𓀴“Should we disturb her?”
11491𓀴
11492𓀴“We must,” said Van Helsing grimly. “If the door be locked, I shall
11493𓀴break it in.”
11494𓀴
11495𓀴“May it not frighten her terribly? It is unusual to break into a lady’s
11496𓀴room!”
11497𓀴
11498𓀴Van Helsing said solemnly, “You are always right; but this is life and
11499𓀴death. All chambers are alike to the doctor; and even were they not they
11500𓀴are all as one to me to-night. Friend John, when I turn the handle, if
11501𓀴the door does not open, do you put your shoulder down and shove; and you
11502𓀴too, my friends. Now!”
11503𓀴
11504𓀴He turned the handle as he spoke, but the door did not yield. We threw
11505𓀴ourselves against it; with a crash it burst open, and we almost fell
11506𓀴headlong into the room. The Professor did actually fall, and I saw
11507𓀴across him as he gathered himself up from hands and knees. What I saw
11508𓀴appalled me. I felt my hair rise like bristles on the back of my neck,
11509𓀴and my heart seemed to stand still.
11510𓀴
11511𓀴The moonlight was so bright that through the thick yellow blind the room
11512𓀴was light enough to see. On the bed beside the window lay Jonathan
11513𓀴Harker, his face flushed and breathing heavily as though in a stupor.
11514𓀴Kneeling on the near edge of the bed facing outwards was the white-clad
11515𓀴figure of his wife. By her side stood a tall, thin man, clad in black.
11516𓀴His face was turned from us, but the instant we saw we all recognised
11517𓀴the Count--in every way, even to the scar on his forehead. With his left
11518𓀴hand he held both Mrs. Harker’s hands, keeping them away with her arms
11519𓀴at full tension; his right hand gripped her by the back of the neck,
11520𓀴forcing her face down on his bosom. Her white nightdress was smeared
11521𓀴with blood, and a thin stream trickled down the man’s bare breast which
11522𓀴was shown by his torn-open dress. The attitude of the two had a terrible
11523𓀴resemblance to a child forcing a kitten’s nose into a saucer of milk to
11524𓀴compel it to drink. As we burst into the room, the Count turned his
11525𓀴face, and the hellish look that I had heard described seemed to leap
11526𓀴into it. His eyes flamed red with devilish passion; the great nostrils
11527𓀴of the white aquiline nose opened wide and quivered at the edge; and the
11528𓀴white sharp teeth, behind the full lips of the blood-dripping mouth,
11529𓀴champed together like those of a wild beast. With a wrench, which threw
11530𓀴his victim back upon the bed as though hurled from a height, he turned
11531𓀴and sprang at us. But by this time the Professor had gained his feet,
11532𓀴and was holding towards him the envelope which contained the Sacred
11533𓀴Wafer. The Count suddenly stopped, just as poor Lucy had done outside
11534𓀴the tomb, and cowered back. Further and further back he cowered, as we,
11535𓀴lifting our crucifixes, advanced. The moonlight suddenly failed, as a
11536𓀴great black cloud sailed across the sky; and when the gaslight sprang up
11537𓀴under Quincey’s match, we saw nothing but a faint vapour. This, as we
11538𓀴looked, trailed under the door, which with the recoil from its bursting
11539𓀴open, had swung back to its old position. Van Helsing, Art, and I moved
11540𓀴forward to Mrs. Harker, who by this time had drawn her breath and with
11541𓀴it had given a scream so wild, so ear-piercing, so despairing that it
11542𓀴seems to me now that it will ring in my ears till my dying day. For a
11543𓀴few seconds she lay in her helpless attitude and disarray. Her face was
11544𓀴ghastly, with a pallor which was accentuated by the blood which smeared
11545𓀴her lips and cheeks and chin; from her throat trickled a thin stream of
11546𓀴blood; her eyes were mad with terror. Then she put before her face her
11547𓀴poor crushed hands, which bore on their whiteness the red mark of the
11548𓀴Count’s terrible grip, and from behind them came a low desolate wail
11549𓀴which made the terrible scream seem only the quick expression of an
11550𓀴endless grief. Van Helsing stepped forward and drew the coverlet gently
11551𓀴over her body, whilst Art, after looking at her face for an instant
11552𓀴despairingly, ran out of the room. Van Helsing whispered to me:--
11553𓀴
11554𓀴“Jonathan is in a stupor such as we know the Vampire can produce. We can
11555𓀴do nothing with poor Madam Mina for a few moments till she recovers
11556𓀴herself; I must wake him!” He dipped the end of a towel in cold water
11557𓀴and with it began to flick him on the face, his wife all the while
11558𓀴holding her face between her hands and sobbing in a way that was
11559𓀴heart-breaking to hear. I raised the blind, and looked out of the
11560𓀴window. There was much moonshine; and as I looked I could see Quincey
11561𓀴Morris run across the lawn and hide himself in the shadow of a great
11562𓀴yew-tree. It puzzled me to think why he was doing this; but at the
11563𓀴instant I heard Harker’s quick exclamation as he woke to partial
11564𓀴consciousness, and turned to the bed. On his face, as there might well
11565𓀴be, was a look of wild amazement. He seemed dazed for a few seconds, and
11566𓀴then full consciousness seemed to burst upon him all at once, and he
11567𓀴started up. His wife was aroused by the quick movement, and turned to
11568𓀴him with her arms stretched out, as though to embrace him; instantly,
11569𓀴however, she drew them in again, and putting her elbows together, held
11570𓀴her hands before her face, and shuddered till the bed beneath her shook.
11571𓀴
11572𓀴“In God’s name what does this mean?” Harker cried out. “Dr. Seward, Dr.
11573𓀴Van Helsing, what is it? What has happened? What is wrong? Mina, dear,
11574𓀴what is it? What does that blood mean? My God, my God! has it come to
11575𓀴this!” and, raising himself to his knees, he beat his hands wildly
11576𓀴together. “Good God help us! help her! oh, help her!” With a quick
11577𓀴movement he jumped from bed, and began to pull on his clothes,--all the
11578𓀴man in him awake at the need for instant exertion. “What has happened?
11579𓀴Tell me all about it!” he cried without pausing. “Dr. Van Helsing, you
11580𓀴love Mina, I know. Oh, do something to save her. It cannot have gone too
11581𓀴far yet. Guard her while I look for _him_!” His wife, through her terror
11582𓀴and horror and distress, saw some sure danger to him: instantly
11583𓀴forgetting her own grief, she seized hold of him and cried out:--
11584𓀴
11585𓀴“No! no! Jonathan, you must not leave me. I have suffered enough
11586𓀴to-night, God knows, without the dread of his harming you. You must stay
11587𓀴with me. Stay with these friends who will watch over you!” Her
11588𓀴expression became frantic as she spoke; and, he yielding to her, she
11589𓀴pulled him down sitting on the bed side, and clung to him fiercely.
11590𓀴
11591𓀴Van Helsing and I tried to calm them both. The Professor held up his
11592𓀴little golden crucifix, and said with wonderful calmness:--
11593𓀴
11594𓀴“Do not fear, my dear. We are here; and whilst this is close to you no
11595𓀴foul thing can approach. You are safe for to-night; and we must be calm
11596𓀴and take counsel together.” She shuddered and was silent, holding down
11597𓀴her head on her husband’s breast. When she raised it, his white
11598𓀴night-robe was stained with blood where her lips had touched, and where
11599𓀴the thin open wound in her neck had sent forth drops. The instant she
11600𓀴saw it she drew back, with a low wail, and whispered, amidst choking
11601𓀴sobs:--
11602𓀴
11603𓀴“Unclean, unclean! I must touch him or kiss him no more. Oh, that it
11604𓀴should be that it is I who am now his worst enemy, and whom he may have
11605𓀴most cause to fear.” To this he spoke out resolutely:--
11606𓀴
11607𓀴“Nonsense, Mina. It is a shame to me to hear such a word. I would not
11608𓀴hear it of you; and I shall not hear it from you. May God judge me by my
11609𓀴deserts, and punish me with more bitter suffering than even this hour,
11610𓀴if by any act or will of mine anything ever come between us!” He put out
11611𓀴his arms and folded her to his breast; and for a while she lay there
11612𓀴sobbing. He looked at us over her bowed head, with eyes that blinked
11613𓀴damply above his quivering nostrils; his mouth was set as steel. After a
11614𓀴while her sobs became less frequent and more faint, and then he said to
11615𓀴me, speaking with a studied calmness which I felt tried his nervous
11616𓀴power to the utmost:--
11617𓀴
11618𓀴“And now, Dr. Seward, tell me all about it. Too well I know the broad
11619𓀴fact; tell me all that has been.” I told him exactly what had happened,
11620𓀴and he listened with seeming impassiveness; but his nostrils twitched
11621𓀴and his eyes blazed as I told how the ruthless hands of the Count had
11622𓀴held his wife in that terrible and horrid position, with her mouth to
11623𓀴the open wound in his breast. It interested me, even at that moment, to
11624𓀴see, that, whilst the face of white set passion worked convulsively over
11625𓀴the bowed head, the hands tenderly and lovingly stroked the ruffled
11626𓀴hair. Just as I had finished, Quincey and Godalming knocked at the door.
11627𓀴They entered in obedience to our summons. Van Helsing looked at me
11628𓀴questioningly. I understood him to mean if we were to take advantage of
11629𓀴their coming to divert if possible the thoughts of the unhappy husband
11630𓀴and wife from each other and from themselves; so on nodding acquiescence
11631𓀴to him he asked them what they had seen or done. To which Lord Godalming
11632𓀴answered:--
11633𓀴
11634𓀴“I could not see him anywhere in the passage, or in any of our rooms. I
11635𓀴looked in the study but, though he had been there, he had gone. He had,
11636𓀴however----” He stopped suddenly, looking at the poor drooping figure on
11637𓀴the bed. Van Helsing said gravely:--
11638𓀴
11639𓀴“Go on, friend Arthur. We want here no more concealments. Our hope now
11640𓀴is in knowing all. Tell freely!” So Art went on:--
11641𓀴
11642𓀴“He had been there, and though it could only have been for a few
11643𓀴seconds, he made rare hay of the place. All the manuscript had been
11644𓀴burned, and the blue flames were flickering amongst the white ashes; the
11645𓀴cylinders of your phonograph too were thrown on the fire, and the wax
11646𓀴had helped the flames.” Here I interrupted. “Thank God there is the
11647𓀴other copy in the safe!” His face lit for a moment, but fell again as he
11648𓀴went on: “I ran downstairs then, but could see no sign of him. I looked
11649𓀴into Renfield’s room; but there was no trace there except----!” Again he
11650𓀴paused. “Go on,” said Harker hoarsely; so he bowed his head and
11651𓀴moistening his lips with his tongue, added: “except that the poor fellow
11652𓀴is dead.” Mrs. Harker raised her head, looking from one to the other of
11653𓀴us she said solemnly:--
11654𓀴
11655𓀴“God’s will be done!” I could not but feel that Art was keeping back
11656𓀴something; but, as I took it that it was with a purpose, I said nothing.
11657𓀴Van Helsing turned to Morris and asked:--
11658𓀴
11659𓀴“And you, friend Quincey, have you any to tell?”
11660𓀴
11661𓀴“A little,” he answered. “It may be much eventually, but at present I
11662𓀴can’t say. I thought it well to know if possible where the Count would
11663𓀴go when he left the house. I did not see him; but I saw a bat rise from
11664𓀴Renfield’s window, and flap westward. I expected to see him in some
11665𓀴shape go back to Carfax; but he evidently sought some other lair. He
11666𓀴will not be back to-night; for the sky is reddening in the east, and the
11667𓀴dawn is close. We must work to-morrow!”
11668𓀴
11669𓀴He said the latter words through his shut teeth. For a space of perhaps
11670𓀴a couple of minutes there was silence, and I could fancy that I could
11671𓀴hear the sound of our hearts beating; then Van Helsing said, placing his
11672𓀴hand very tenderly on Mrs. Harker’s head:--
11673𓀴
11674𓀴“And now, Madam Mina--poor, dear, dear Madam Mina--tell us exactly what
11675𓀴happened. God knows that I do not want that you be pained; but it is
11676𓀴need that we know all. For now more than ever has all work to be done
11677𓀴quick and sharp, and in deadly earnest. The day is close to us that must
11678𓀴end all, if it may be so; and now is the chance that we may live and
11679𓀴learn.”
11680𓀴
11681𓀴The poor, dear lady shivered, and I could see the tension of her nerves
11682𓀴as she clasped her husband closer to her and bent her head lower and
11683𓀴lower still on his breast. Then she raised her head proudly, and held
11684𓀴out one hand to Van Helsing who took it in his, and, after stooping and
11685𓀴kissing it reverently, held it fast. The other hand was locked in that
11686𓀴of her husband, who held his other arm thrown round her protectingly.
11687𓀴After a pause in which she was evidently ordering her thoughts, she
11688𓀴began:--
11689𓀴
11690𓀴“I took the sleeping draught which you had so kindly given me, but for a
11691𓀴long time it did not act. I seemed to become more wakeful, and myriads
11692𓀴of horrible fancies began to crowd in upon my mind--all of them
11693𓀴connected with death, and vampires; with blood, and pain, and trouble.”
11694𓀴Her husband involuntarily groaned as she turned to him and said
11695𓀴lovingly: “Do not fret, dear. You must be brave and strong, and help me
11696𓀴through the horrible task. If you only knew what an effort it is to me
11697𓀴to tell of this fearful thing at all, you would understand how much I
11698𓀴need your help. Well, I saw I must try to help the medicine to its work
11699𓀴with my will, if it was to do me any good, so I resolutely set myself to
11700𓀴sleep. Sure enough sleep must soon have come to me, for I remember no
11701𓀴more. Jonathan coming in had not waked me, for he lay by my side when
11702𓀴next I remember. There was in the room the same thin white mist that I
11703𓀴had before noticed. But I forget now if you know of this; you will find
11704𓀴it in my diary which I shall show you later. I felt the same vague
11705𓀴terror which had come to me before and the same sense of some presence.
11706𓀴I turned to wake Jonathan, but found that he slept so soundly that it
11707𓀴seemed as if it was he who had taken the sleeping draught, and not I. I
11708𓀴tried, but I could not wake him. This caused me a great fear, and I
11709𓀴looked around terrified. Then indeed, my heart sank within me: beside
11710𓀴the bed, as if he had stepped out of the mist--or rather as if the mist
11711𓀴had turned into his figure, for it had entirely disappeared--stood a
11712𓀴tall, thin man, all in black. I knew him at once from the description of
11713𓀴the others. The waxen face; the high aquiline nose, on which the light
11714𓀴fell in a thin white line; the parted red lips, with the sharp white
11715𓀴teeth showing between; and the red eyes that I had seemed to see in the
11716𓀴sunset on the windows of St. Mary’s Church at Whitby. I knew, too, the
11717𓀴red scar on his forehead where Jonathan had struck him. For an instant
11718𓀴my heart stood still, and I would have screamed out, only that I was
11719𓀴paralysed. In the pause he spoke in a sort of keen, cutting whisper,
11720𓀴pointing as he spoke to Jonathan:--
11721𓀴
11722𓀴“‘Silence! If you make a sound I shall take him and dash his brains out
11723𓀴before your very eyes.’ I was appalled and was too bewildered to do or
11724𓀴say anything. With a mocking smile, he placed one hand upon my shoulder
11725𓀴and, holding me tight, bared my throat with the other, saying as he did
11726𓀴so, ‘First, a little refreshment to reward my exertions. You may as well
11727𓀴be quiet; it is not the first time, or the second, that your veins have
11728𓀴appeased my thirst!’ I was bewildered, and, strangely enough, I did not
11729𓀴want to hinder him. I suppose it is a part of the horrible curse that
11730𓀴such is, when his touch is on his victim. And oh, my God, my God, pity
11731𓀴me! He placed his reeking lips upon my throat!” Her husband groaned
11732𓀴again. She clasped his hand harder, and looked at him pityingly, as if
11733𓀴he were the injured one, and went on:--
11734𓀴
11735𓀴“I felt my strength fading away, and I was in a half swoon. How long
11736𓀴this horrible thing lasted I know not; but it seemed that a long time
11737𓀴must have passed before he took his foul, awful, sneering mouth away. I
11738𓀴saw it drip with the fresh blood!” The remembrance seemed for a while to
11739𓀴overpower her, and she drooped and would have sunk down but for her
11740𓀴husband’s sustaining arm. With a great effort she recovered herself and
11741𓀴went on:--
11742𓀴
11743𓀴“Then he spoke to me mockingly, ‘And so you, like the others, would play
11744𓀴your brains against mine. You would help these men to hunt me and
11745𓀴frustrate me in my designs! You know now, and they know in part already,
11746𓀴and will know in full before long, what it is to cross my path. They
11747𓀴should have kept their energies for use closer to home. Whilst they
11748𓀴played wits against me--against me who commanded nations, and intrigued
11749𓀴for them, and fought for them, hundreds of years before they were
11750𓀴born--I was countermining them. And you, their best beloved one, are now
11751𓀴to me, flesh of my flesh; blood of my blood; kin of my kin; my bountiful
11752𓀴wine-press for a while; and shall be later on my companion and my
11753𓀴helper. You shall be avenged in turn; for not one of them but shall
11754𓀴minister to your needs. But as yet you are to be punished for what you
11755𓀴have done. You have aided in thwarting me; now you shall come to my
11756𓀴call. When my brain says “Come!” to you, you shall cross land or sea to
11757𓀴do my bidding; and to that end this!’ With that he pulled open his
11758𓀴shirt, and with his long sharp nails opened a vein in his breast. When
11759𓀴the blood began to spurt out, he took my hands in one of his, holding
11760𓀴them tight, and with the other seized my neck and pressed my mouth to
11761𓀴the wound, so that I must either suffocate or swallow some of the---- Oh
11762𓀴my God! my God! what have I done? What have I done to deserve such a
11763𓀴fate, I who have tried to walk in meekness and righteousness all my
11764𓀴days. God pity me! Look down on a poor soul in worse than mortal peril;
11765𓀴and in mercy pity those to whom she is dear!” Then she began to rub her
11766𓀴lips as though to cleanse them from pollution.
11767𓀴
11768𓀴As she was telling her terrible story, the eastern sky began to quicken,
11769𓀴and everything became more and more clear. Harker was still and quiet;
11770𓀴but over his face, as the awful narrative went on, came a grey look
11771𓀴which deepened and deepened in the morning light, till when the first
11772𓀴red streak of the coming dawn shot up, the flesh stood darkly out
11773𓀴against the whitening hair.
11774𓀴
11775𓀴We have arranged that one of us is to stay within call of the unhappy
11776𓀴pair till we can meet together and arrange about taking action.
11777𓀴
11778𓀴Of this I am sure: the sun rises to-day on no more miserable house in
11779𓀴all the great round of its daily course.
11780𓀴
11781𓀴
11782𓀴
11783𓀴
11784𓀴CHAPTER XXII
11785𓀴
11786𓀴JONATHAN HARKER’S JOURNAL
11787𓀴
11788𓀴
11789𓀴_3 October._--As I must do something or go mad, I write this diary. It
11790𓀴is now six o’clock, and we are to meet in the study in half an hour and
11791𓀴take something to eat; for Dr. Van Helsing and Dr. Seward are agreed
11792𓀴that if we do not eat we cannot work our best. Our best will be, God
11793𓀴knows, required to-day. I must keep writing at every chance, for I dare
11794𓀴not stop to think. All, big and little, must go down; perhaps at the end
11795𓀴the little things may teach us most. The teaching, big or little, could
11796𓀴not have landed Mina or me anywhere worse than we are to-day. However,
11797𓀴we must trust and hope. Poor Mina told me just now, with the tears
11798𓀴running down her dear cheeks, that it is in trouble and trial that our
11799𓀴faith is tested--that we must keep on trusting; and that God will aid us
11800𓀴up to the end. The end! oh my God! what end?... To work! To work!
11801𓀴
11802𓀴When Dr. Van Helsing and Dr. Seward had come back from seeing poor
11803𓀴Renfield, we went gravely into what was to be done. First, Dr. Seward
11804𓀴told us that when he and Dr. Van Helsing had gone down to the room below
11805𓀴they had found Renfield lying on the floor, all in a heap. His face was
11806𓀴all bruised and crushed in, and the bones of the neck were broken.
11807𓀴
11808𓀴Dr. Seward asked the attendant who was on duty in the passage if he had
11809𓀴heard anything. He said that he had been sitting down--he confessed to
11810𓀴half dozing--when he heard loud voices in the room, and then Renfield
11811𓀴had called out loudly several times, “God! God! God!” after that there
11812𓀴was a sound of falling, and when he entered the room he found him lying
11813𓀴on the floor, face down, just as the doctors had seen him. Van Helsing
11814𓀴asked if he had heard “voices” or “a voice,” and he said he could not
11815𓀴say; that at first it had seemed to him as if there were two, but as
11816𓀴there was no one in the room it could have been only one. He could swear
11817𓀴to it, if required, that the word “God” was spoken by the patient. Dr.
11818𓀴Seward said to us, when we were alone, that he did not wish to go into
11819𓀴the matter; the question of an inquest had to be considered, and it
11820𓀴would never do to put forward the truth, as no one would believe it. As
11821𓀴it was, he thought that on the attendant’s evidence he could give a
11822𓀴certificate of death by misadventure in falling from bed. In case the
11823𓀴coroner should demand it, there would be a formal inquest, necessarily
11824𓀴to the same result.
11825𓀴
11826𓀴When the question began to be discussed as to what should be our next
11827𓀴step, the very first thing we decided was that Mina should be in full
11828𓀴confidence; that nothing of any sort--no matter how painful--should be
11829𓀴kept from her. She herself agreed as to its wisdom, and it was pitiful
11830𓀴to see her so brave and yet so sorrowful, and in such a depth of
11831𓀴despair. “There must be no concealment,” she said, “Alas! we have had
11832𓀴too much already. And besides there is nothing in all the world that can
11833𓀴give me more pain than I have already endured--than I suffer now!
11834𓀴Whatever may happen, it must be of new hope or of new courage to me!”
11835𓀴Van Helsing was looking at her fixedly as she spoke, and said, suddenly
11836𓀴but quietly:--
11837𓀴
11838𓀴“But dear Madam Mina, are you not afraid; not for yourself, but for
11839𓀴others from yourself, after what has happened?” Her face grew set in its
11840𓀴lines, but her eyes shone with the devotion of a martyr as she
11841𓀴answered:--
11842𓀴
11843𓀴“Ah no! for my mind is made up!”
11844𓀴
11845𓀴“To what?” he asked gently, whilst we were all very still; for each in
11846𓀴our own way we had a sort of vague idea of what she meant. Her answer
11847𓀴came with direct simplicity, as though she were simply stating a fact:--
11848𓀴
11849𓀴“Because if I find in myself--and I shall watch keenly for it--a sign of
11850𓀴harm to any that I love, I shall die!”
11851𓀴
11852𓀴“You would not kill yourself?” he asked, hoarsely.
11853𓀴
11854𓀴“I would; if there were no friend who loved me, who would save me such a
11855𓀴pain, and so desperate an effort!” She looked at him meaningly as she
11856𓀴spoke. He was sitting down; but now he rose and came close to her and
11857𓀴put his hand on her head as he said solemnly:
11858𓀴
11859𓀴“My child, there is such an one if it were for your good. For myself I
11860𓀴could hold it in my account with God to find such an euthanasia for you,
11861𓀴even at this moment if it were best. Nay, were it safe! But my
11862𓀴child----” For a moment he seemed choked, and a great sob rose in his
11863𓀴throat; he gulped it down and went on:--
11864𓀴
11865𓀴“There are here some who would stand between you and death. You must not
11866𓀴die. You must not die by any hand; but least of all by your own. Until
11867𓀴the other, who has fouled your sweet life, is true dead you must not
11868𓀴die; for if he is still with the quick Un-Dead, your death would make
11869𓀴you even as he is. No, you must live! You must struggle and strive to
11870𓀴live, though death would seem a boon unspeakable. You must fight Death
11871𓀴himself, though he come to you in pain or in joy; by the day, or the
11872𓀴night; in safety or in peril! On your living soul I charge you that you
11873𓀴do not die--nay, nor think of death--till this great evil be past.” The
11874𓀴poor dear grew white as death, and shock and shivered, as I have seen a
11875𓀴quicksand shake and shiver at the incoming of the tide. We were all
11876𓀴silent; we could do nothing. At length she grew more calm and turning to
11877𓀴him said, sweetly, but oh! so sorrowfully, as she held out her hand:--
11878𓀴
11879𓀴“I promise you, my dear friend, that if God will let me live, I shall
11880𓀴strive to do so; till, if it may be in His good time, this horror may
11881𓀴have passed away from me.” She was so good and brave that we all felt
11882𓀴that our hearts were strengthened to work and endure for her, and we
11883𓀴began to discuss what we were to do. I told her that she was to have all
11884𓀴the papers in the safe, and all the papers or diaries and phonographs we
11885𓀴might hereafter use; and was to keep the record as she had done before.
11886𓀴She was pleased with the prospect of anything to do--if “pleased” could
11887𓀴be used in connection with so grim an interest.
11888𓀴
11889𓀴As usual Van Helsing had thought ahead of everyone else, and was
11890𓀴prepared with an exact ordering of our work.
11891𓀴
11892𓀴“It is perhaps well,” he said, “that at our meeting after our visit to
11893𓀴Carfax we decided not to do anything with the earth-boxes that lay
11894𓀴there. Had we done so, the Count must have guessed our purpose, and
11895𓀴would doubtless have taken measures in advance to frustrate such an
11896𓀴effort with regard to the others; but now he does not know our
11897𓀴intentions. Nay, more, in all probability, he does not know that such a
11898𓀴power exists to us as can sterilise his lairs, so that he cannot use
11899𓀴them as of old. We are now so much further advanced in our knowledge as
11900𓀴to their disposition that, when we have examined the house in
11901𓀴Piccadilly, we may track the very last of them. To-day, then, is ours;
11902𓀴and in it rests our hope. The sun that rose on our sorrow this morning
11903𓀴guards us in its course. Until it sets to-night, that monster must
11904𓀴retain whatever form he now has. He is confined within the limitations
11905𓀴of his earthly envelope. He cannot melt into thin air nor disappear
11906𓀴through cracks or chinks or crannies. If he go through a doorway, he
11907𓀴must open the door like a mortal. And so we have this day to hunt out
11908𓀴all his lairs and sterilise them. So we shall, if we have not yet catch
11909𓀴him and destroy him, drive him to bay in some place where the catching
11910𓀴and the destroying shall be, in time, sure.” Here I started up for I
11911𓀴could not contain myself at the thought that the minutes and seconds so
11912𓀴preciously laden with Mina’s life and happiness were flying from us,
11913𓀴since whilst we talked action was impossible. But Van Helsing held up
11914𓀴his hand warningly. “Nay, friend Jonathan,” he said, “in this, the
11915𓀴quickest way home is the longest way, so your proverb say. We shall all
11916𓀴act and act with desperate quick, when the time has come. But think, in
11917𓀴all probable the key of the situation is in that house in Piccadilly.
11918𓀴The Count may have many houses which he has bought. Of them he will have
11919𓀴deeds of purchase, keys and other things. He will have paper that he
11920𓀴write on; he will have his book of cheques. There are many belongings
11921𓀴that he must have somewhere; why not in this place so central, so quiet,
11922𓀴where he come and go by the front or the back at all hour, when in the
11923𓀴very vast of the traffic there is none to notice. We shall go there and
11924𓀴search that house; and when we learn what it holds, then we do what our
11925𓀴friend Arthur call, in his phrases of hunt ‘stop the earths’ and so we
11926𓀴run down our old fox--so? is it not?”
11927𓀴
11928𓀴“Then let us come at once,” I cried, “we are wasting the precious,
11929𓀴precious time!” The Professor did not move, but simply said:--
11930𓀴
11931𓀴“And how are we to get into that house in Piccadilly?”
11932𓀴
11933𓀴“Any way!” I cried. “We shall break in if need be.”
11934𓀴
11935𓀴“And your police; where will they be, and what will they say?”
11936𓀴
11937𓀴I was staggered; but I knew that if he wished to delay he had a good
11938𓀴reason for it. So I said, as quietly as I could:--
11939𓀴
11940𓀴“Don’t wait more than need be; you know, I am sure, what torture I am
11941𓀴in.”
11942𓀴
11943𓀴“Ah, my child, that I do; and indeed there is no wish of me to add to
11944𓀴your anguish. But just think, what can we do, until all the world be at
11945𓀴movement. Then will come our time. I have thought and thought, and it
11946𓀴seems to me that the simplest way is the best of all. Now we wish to get
11947𓀴into the house, but we have no key; is it not so?” I nodded.
11948𓀴
11949𓀴“Now suppose that you were, in truth, the owner of that house, and could
11950𓀴not still get in; and think there was to you no conscience of the
11951𓀴housebreaker, what would you do?”
11952𓀴
11953𓀴“I should get a respectable locksmith, and set him to work to pick the
11954𓀴lock for me.”
11955𓀴
11956𓀴“And your police, they would interfere, would they not?”
11957𓀴
11958𓀴“Oh, no! not if they knew the man was properly employed.”
11959𓀴
11960𓀴“Then,” he looked at me as keenly as he spoke, “all that is in doubt is
11961𓀴the conscience of the employer, and the belief of your policemen as to
11962𓀴whether or no that employer has a good conscience or a bad one. Your
11963𓀴police must indeed be zealous men and clever--oh, so clever!--in reading
11964𓀴the heart, that they trouble themselves in such matter. No, no, my
11965𓀴friend Jonathan, you go take the lock off a hundred empty house in this
11966𓀴your London, or of any city in the world; and if you do it as such
11967𓀴things are rightly done, and at the time such things are rightly done,
11968𓀴no one will interfere. I have read of a gentleman who owned a so fine
11969𓀴house in London, and when he went for months of summer to Switzerland
11970𓀴and lock up his house, some burglar came and broke window at back and
11971𓀴got in. Then he went and made open the shutters in front and walk out
11972𓀴and in through the door, before the very eyes of the police. Then he
11973𓀴have an auction in that house, and advertise it, and put up big notice;
11974𓀴and when the day come he sell off by a great auctioneer all the goods of
11975𓀴that other man who own them. Then he go to a builder, and he sell him
11976𓀴that house, making an agreement that he pull it down and take all away
11977𓀴within a certain time. And your police and other authority help him all
11978𓀴they can. And when that owner come back from his holiday in Switzerland
11979𓀴he find only an empty hole where his house had been. This was all done
11980𓀴_en règle_; and in our work we shall be _en règle_ too. We shall not go
11981𓀴so early that the policemen who have then little to think of, shall deem
11982𓀴it strange; but we shall go after ten o’clock, when there are many
11983𓀴about, and such things would be done were we indeed owners of the
11984𓀴house.”
11985𓀴
11986𓀴I could not but see how right he was and the terrible despair of Mina’s
11987𓀴face became relaxed a thought; there was hope in such good counsel. Van
11988𓀴Helsing went on:--
11989𓀴
11990𓀴“When once within that house we may find more clues; at any rate some of
11991𓀴us can remain there whilst the rest find the other places where there be
11992𓀴more earth-boxes--at Bermondsey and Mile End.”
11993𓀴
11994𓀴Lord Godalming stood up. “I can be of some use here,” he said. “I shall
11995𓀴wire to my people to have horses and carriages where they will be most
11996𓀴convenient.”
11997𓀴
11998𓀴“Look here, old fellow,” said Morris, “it is a capital idea to have all
11999𓀴ready in case we want to go horsebacking; but don’t you think that one
12000𓀴of your snappy carriages with its heraldic adornments in a byway of
12001𓀴Walworth or Mile End would attract too much attention for our purposes?
12002𓀴It seems to me that we ought to take cabs when we go south or east; and
12003𓀴even leave them somewhere near the neighbourhood we are going to.”
12004𓀴
12005𓀴“Friend Quincey is right!” said the Professor. “His head is what you
12006𓀴call in plane with the horizon. It is a difficult thing that we go to
12007𓀴do, and we do not want no peoples to watch us if so it may.”
12008𓀴
12009𓀴Mina took a growing interest in everything and I was rejoiced to see
12010𓀴that the exigency of affairs was helping her to forget for a time the
12011𓀴terrible experience of the night. She was very, very pale--almost
12012𓀴ghastly, and so thin that her lips were drawn away, showing her teeth in
12013𓀴somewhat of prominence. I did not mention this last, lest it should give
12014𓀴her needless pain; but it made my blood run cold in my veins to think of
12015𓀴what had occurred with poor Lucy when the Count had sucked her blood. As
12016𓀴yet there was no sign of the teeth growing sharper; but the time as yet
12017𓀴was short, and there was time for fear.
12018𓀴
12019𓀴When we came to the discussion of the sequence of our efforts and of the
12020𓀴disposition of our forces, there were new sources of doubt. It was
12021𓀴finally agreed that before starting for Piccadilly we should destroy the
12022𓀴Count’s lair close at hand. In case he should find it out too soon, we
12023𓀴should thus be still ahead of him in our work of destruction; and his
12024𓀴presence in his purely material shape, and at his weakest, might give us
12025𓀴some new clue.
12026𓀴
12027𓀴As to the disposal of forces, it was suggested by the Professor that,
12028𓀴after our visit to Carfax, we should all enter the house in Piccadilly;
12029𓀴that the two doctors and I should remain there, whilst Lord Godalming
12030𓀴and Quincey found the lairs at Walworth and Mile End and destroyed them.
12031𓀴It was possible, if not likely, the Professor urged, that the Count
12032𓀴might appear in Piccadilly during the day, and that if so we might be
12033𓀴able to cope with him then and there. At any rate, we might be able to
12034𓀴follow him in force. To this plan I strenuously objected, and so far as
12035𓀴my going was concerned, for I said that I intended to stay and protect
12036𓀴Mina, I thought that my mind was made up on the subject; but Mina would
12037𓀴not listen to my objection. She said that there might be some law matter
12038𓀴in which I could be useful; that amongst the Count’s papers might be
12039𓀴some clue which I could understand out of my experience in Transylvania;
12040𓀴and that, as it was, all the strength we could muster was required to
12041𓀴cope with the Count’s extraordinary power. I had to give in, for Mina’s
12042𓀴resolution was fixed; she said that it was the last hope for _her_ that
12043𓀴we should all work together. “As for me,” she said, “I have no fear.
12044𓀴Things have been as bad as they can be; and whatever may happen must
12045𓀴have in it some element of hope or comfort. Go, my husband! God can, if
12046𓀴He wishes it, guard me as well alone as with any one present.” So I
12047𓀴started up crying out: “Then in God’s name let us come at once, for we
12048𓀴are losing time. The Count may come to Piccadilly earlier than we
12049𓀴think.”
12050𓀴
12051𓀴“Not so!” said Van Helsing, holding up his hand.
12052𓀴
12053𓀴“But why?” I asked.
12054𓀴
12055𓀴“Do you forget,” he said, with actually a smile, “that last night he
12056𓀴banqueted heavily, and will sleep late?”
12057𓀴
12058𓀴Did I forget! shall I ever--can I ever! Can any of us ever forget that
12059𓀴terrible scene! Mina struggled hard to keep her brave countenance; but
12060𓀴the pain overmastered her and she put her hands before her face, and
12061𓀴shuddered whilst she moaned. Van Helsing had not intended to recall her
12062𓀴frightful experience. He had simply lost sight of her and her part in
12063𓀴the affair in his intellectual effort. When it struck him what he said,
12064𓀴he was horrified at his thoughtlessness and tried to comfort her. “Oh,
12065𓀴Madam Mina,” he said, “dear, dear Madam Mina, alas! that I of all who so
12066𓀴reverence you should have said anything so forgetful. These stupid old
12067𓀴lips of mine and this stupid old head do not deserve so; but you will
12068𓀴forget it, will you not?” He bent low beside her as he spoke; she took
12069𓀴his hand, and looking at him through her tears, said hoarsely:--
12070𓀴
12071𓀴“No, I shall not forget, for it is well that I remember; and with it I
12072𓀴have so much in memory of you that is sweet, that I take it all
12073𓀴together. Now, you must all be going soon. Breakfast is ready, and we
12074𓀴must all eat that we may be strong.”
12075𓀴
12076𓀴Breakfast was a strange meal to us all. We tried to be cheerful and
12077𓀴encourage each other, and Mina was the brightest and most cheerful of
12078𓀴us. When it was over, Van Helsing stood up and said:--
12079𓀴
12080𓀴“Now, my dear friends, we go forth to our terrible enterprise. Are we
12081𓀴all armed, as we were on that night when first we visited our enemy’s
12082𓀴lair; armed against ghostly as well as carnal attack?” We all assured
12083𓀴him. “Then it is well. Now, Madam Mina, you are in any case _quite_ safe
12084𓀴here until the sunset; and before then we shall return--if---- We shall
12085𓀴return! But before we go let me see you armed against personal attack. I
12086𓀴have myself, since you came down, prepared your chamber by the placing
12087𓀴of things of which we know, so that He may not enter. Now let me guard
12088𓀴yourself. On your forehead I touch this piece of Sacred Wafer in the
12089𓀴name of the Father, the Son, and----”
12090𓀴
12091𓀴There was a fearful scream which almost froze our hearts to hear. As he
12092𓀴had placed the Wafer on Mina’s forehead, it had seared it--had burned
12093𓀴into the flesh as though it had been a piece of white-hot metal. My poor
12094𓀴darling’s brain had told her the significance of the fact as quickly as
12095𓀴her nerves received the pain of it; and the two so overwhelmed her that
12096𓀴her overwrought nature had its voice in that dreadful scream. But the
12097𓀴words to her thought came quickly; the echo of the scream had not ceased
12098𓀴to ring on the air when there came the reaction, and she sank on her
12099𓀴knees on the floor in an agony of abasement. Pulling her beautiful hair
12100𓀴over her face, as the leper of old his mantle, she wailed out:--
12101𓀴
12102𓀴“Unclean! Unclean! Even the Almighty shuns my polluted flesh! I must
12103𓀴bear this mark of shame upon my forehead until the Judgment Day.” They
12104𓀴all paused. I had thrown myself beside her in an agony of helpless
12105𓀴grief, and putting my arms around held her tight. For a few minutes our
12106𓀴sorrowful hearts beat together, whilst the friends around us turned away
12107𓀴their eyes that ran tears silently. Then Van Helsing turned and said
12108𓀴gravely; so gravely that I could not help feeling that he was in some
12109𓀴way inspired, and was stating things outside himself:--
12110𓀴
12111𓀴“It may be that you may have to bear that mark till God himself see fit,
12112𓀴as He most surely shall, on the Judgment Day, to redress all wrongs of
12113𓀴the earth and of His children that He has placed thereon. And oh, Madam
12114𓀴Mina, my dear, my dear, may we who love you be there to see, when that
12115𓀴red scar, the sign of God’s knowledge of what has been, shall pass away,
12116𓀴and leave your forehead as pure as the heart we know. For so surely as
