12117𓀴we live, that scar shall pass away when God sees right to lift the
12118𓀴burden that is hard upon us. Till then we bear our Cross, as His Son did
12119𓀴in obedience to His Will. It may be that we are chosen instruments of
12120𓀴His good pleasure, and that we ascend to His bidding as that other
12121𓀴through stripes and shame; through tears and blood; through doubts and
12122𓀴fears, and all that makes the difference between God and man.”
12123𓀴
12124𓀴There was hope in his words, and comfort; and they made for resignation.
12125𓀴Mina and I both felt so, and simultaneously we each took one of the old
12126𓀴man’s hands and bent over and kissed it. Then without a word we all
12127𓀴knelt down together, and, all holding hands, swore to be true to each
12128𓀴other. We men pledged ourselves to raise the veil of sorrow from the
12129𓀴head of her whom, each in his own way, we loved; and we prayed for help
12130𓀴and guidance in the terrible task which lay before us.
12131𓀴
12132𓀴It was then time to start. So I said farewell to Mina, a parting which
12133𓀴neither of us shall forget to our dying day; and we set out.
12134𓀴
12135𓀴To one thing I have made up my mind: if we find out that Mina must be a
12136𓀴vampire in the end, then she shall not go into that unknown and terrible
12137𓀴land alone. I suppose it is thus that in old times one vampire meant
12138𓀴many; just as their hideous bodies could only rest in sacred earth, so
12139𓀴the holiest love was the recruiting sergeant for their ghastly ranks.
12140𓀴
12141𓀴We entered Carfax without trouble and found all things the same as on
12142𓀴the first occasion. It was hard to believe that amongst so prosaic
12143𓀴surroundings of neglect and dust and decay there was any ground for such
12144𓀴fear as already we knew. Had not our minds been made up, and had there
12145𓀴not been terrible memories to spur us on, we could hardly have proceeded
12146𓀴with our task. We found no papers, or any sign of use in the house; and
12147𓀴in the old chapel the great boxes looked just as we had seen them last.
12148𓀴Dr. Van Helsing said to us solemnly as we stood before them:--
12149𓀴
12150𓀴“And now, my friends, we have a duty here to do. We must sterilise this
12151𓀴earth, so sacred of holy memories, that he has brought from a far
12152𓀴distant land for such fell use. He has chosen this earth because it has
12153𓀴been holy. Thus we defeat him with his own weapon, for we make it more
12154𓀴holy still. It was sanctified to such use of man, now we sanctify it to
12155𓀴God.” As he spoke he took from his bag a screwdriver and a wrench, and
12156𓀴very soon the top of one of the cases was thrown open. The earth smelled
12157𓀴musty and close; but we did not somehow seem to mind, for our attention
12158𓀴was concentrated on the Professor. Taking from his box a piece of the
12159𓀴Sacred Wafer he laid it reverently on the earth, and then shutting down
12160𓀴the lid began to screw it home, we aiding him as he worked.
12161𓀴
12162𓀴One by one we treated in the same way each of the great boxes, and left
12163𓀴them as we had found them to all appearance; but in each was a portion
12164𓀴of the Host.
12165𓀴
12166𓀴When we closed the door behind us, the Professor said solemnly:--
12167𓀴
12168𓀴“So much is already done. If it may be that with all the others we can
12169𓀴be so successful, then the sunset of this evening may shine on Madam
12170𓀴Mina’s forehead all white as ivory and with no stain!”
12171𓀴
12172𓀴As we passed across the lawn on our way to the station to catch our
12173𓀴train we could see the front of the asylum. I looked eagerly, and in the
12174𓀴window of my own room saw Mina. I waved my hand to her, and nodded to
12175𓀴tell that our work there was successfully accomplished. She nodded in
12176𓀴reply to show that she understood. The last I saw, she was waving her
12177𓀴hand in farewell. It was with a heavy heart that we sought the station
12178𓀴and just caught the train, which was steaming in as we reached the
12179𓀴platform.
12180𓀴
12181𓀴I have written this in the train.
12182𓀴
12183𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
12184𓀴
12185𓀴_Piccadilly, 12:30 o’clock._--Just before we reached Fenchurch Street
12186𓀴Lord Godalming said to me:--
12187𓀴
12188𓀴“Quincey and I will find a locksmith. You had better not come with us in
12189𓀴case there should be any difficulty; for under the circumstances it
12190𓀴wouldn’t seem so bad for us to break into an empty house. But you are a
12191𓀴solicitor and the Incorporated Law Society might tell you that you
12192𓀴should have known better.” I demurred as to my not sharing any danger
12193𓀴even of odium, but he went on: “Besides, it will attract less attention
12194𓀴if there are not too many of us. My title will make it all right with
12195𓀴the locksmith, and with any policeman that may come along. You had
12196𓀴better go with Jack and the Professor and stay in the Green Park,
12197𓀴somewhere in sight of the house; and when you see the door opened and
12198𓀴the smith has gone away, do you all come across. We shall be on the
12199𓀴lookout for you, and shall let you in.”
12200𓀴
12201𓀴“The advice is good!” said Van Helsing, so we said no more. Godalming
12202𓀴and Morris hurried off in a cab, we following in another. At the corner
12203𓀴of Arlington Street our contingent got out and strolled into the Green
12204𓀴Park. My heart beat as I saw the house on which so much of our hope was
12205𓀴centred, looming up grim and silent in its deserted condition amongst
12206𓀴its more lively and spruce-looking neighbours. We sat down on a bench
12207𓀴within good view, and began to smoke cigars so as to attract as little
12208𓀴attention as possible. The minutes seemed to pass with leaden feet as we
12209𓀴waited for the coming of the others.
12210𓀴
12211𓀴At length we saw a four-wheeler drive up. Out of it, in leisurely
12212𓀴fashion, got Lord Godalming and Morris; and down from the box descended
12213𓀴a thick-set working man with his rush-woven basket of tools. Morris paid
12214𓀴the cabman, who touched his hat and drove away. Together the two
12215𓀴ascended the steps, and Lord Godalming pointed out what he wanted done.
12216𓀴The workman took off his coat leisurely and hung it on one of the spikes
12217𓀴of the rail, saying something to a policeman who just then sauntered
12218𓀴along. The policeman nodded acquiescence, and the man kneeling down
12219𓀴placed his bag beside him. After searching through it, he took out a
12220𓀴selection of tools which he produced to lay beside him in orderly
12221𓀴fashion. Then he stood up, looked into the keyhole, blew into it, and
12222𓀴turning to his employers, made some remark. Lord Godalming smiled, and
12223𓀴the man lifted a good-sized bunch of keys; selecting one of them, he
12224𓀴began to probe the lock, as if feeling his way with it. After fumbling
12225𓀴about for a bit he tried a second, and then a third. All at once the
12226𓀴door opened under a slight push from him, and he and the two others
12227𓀴entered the hall. We sat still; my own cigar burnt furiously, but Van
12228𓀴Helsing’s went cold altogether. We waited patiently as we saw the
12229𓀴workman come out and bring in his bag. Then he held the door partly
12230𓀴open, steadying it with his knees, whilst he fitted a key to the lock.
12231𓀴This he finally handed to Lord Godalming, who took out his purse and
12232𓀴gave him something. The man touched his hat, took his bag, put on his
12233𓀴coat and departed; not a soul took the slightest notice of the whole
12234𓀴transaction.
12235𓀴
12236𓀴When the man had fairly gone, we three crossed the street and knocked at
12237𓀴the door. It was immediately opened by Quincey Morris, beside whom stood
12238𓀴Lord Godalming lighting a cigar.
12239𓀴
12240𓀴“The place smells so vilely,” said the latter as we came in. It did
12241𓀴indeed smell vilely--like the old chapel at Carfax--and with our
12242𓀴previous experience it was plain to us that the Count had been using the
12243𓀴place pretty freely. We moved to explore the house, all keeping together
12244𓀴in case of attack; for we knew we had a strong and wily enemy to deal
12245𓀴with, and as yet we did not know whether the Count might not be in the
12246𓀴house. In the dining-room, which lay at the back of the hall, we found
12247𓀴eight boxes of earth. Eight boxes only out of the nine, which we sought!
12248𓀴Our work was not over, and would never be until we should have found the
12249𓀴missing box. First we opened the shutters of the window which looked out
12250𓀴across a narrow stone-flagged yard at the blank face of a stable,
12251𓀴pointed to look like the front of a miniature house. There were no
12252𓀴windows in it, so we were not afraid of being over-looked. We did not
12253𓀴lose any time in examining the chests. With the tools which we had
12254𓀴brought with us we opened them, one by one, and treated them as we had
12255𓀴treated those others in the old chapel. It was evident to us that the
12256𓀴Count was not at present in the house, and we proceeded to search for
12257𓀴any of his effects.
12258𓀴
12259𓀴After a cursory glance at the rest of the rooms, from basement to attic,
12260𓀴we came to the conclusion that the dining-room contained any effects
12261𓀴which might belong to the Count; and so we proceeded to minutely examine
12262𓀴them. They lay in a sort of orderly disorder on the great dining-room
12263𓀴table. There were title deeds of the Piccadilly house in a great bundle;
12264𓀴deeds of the purchase of the houses at Mile End and Bermondsey;
12265𓀴note-paper, envelopes, and pens and ink. All were covered up in thin
12266𓀴wrapping paper to keep them from the dust. There were also a clothes
12267𓀴brush, a brush and comb, and a jug and basin--the latter containing
12268𓀴dirty water which was reddened as if with blood. Last of all was a
12269𓀴little heap of keys of all sorts and sizes, probably those belonging to
12270𓀴the other houses. When we had examined this last find, Lord Godalming
12271𓀴and Quincey Morris taking accurate notes of the various addresses of the
12272𓀴houses in the East and the South, took with them the keys in a great
12273𓀴bunch, and set out to destroy the boxes in these places. The rest of us
12274𓀴are, with what patience we can, waiting their return--or the coming of
12275𓀴the Count.
12276𓀴
12277𓀴
12278𓀴
12279𓀴
12280𓀴CHAPTER XXIII
12281𓀴
12282𓀴DR. SEWARD’S DIARY
12283𓀴
12284𓀴
12285𓀴_3 October._--The time seemed terribly long whilst we were waiting for
12286𓀴the coming of Godalming and Quincey Morris. The Professor tried to keep
12287𓀴our minds active by using them all the time. I could see his beneficent
12288𓀴purpose, by the side glances which he threw from time to time at Harker.
12289𓀴The poor fellow is overwhelmed in a misery that is appalling to see.
12290𓀴Last night he was a frank, happy-looking man, with strong, youthful
12291𓀴face, full of energy, and with dark brown hair. To-day he is a drawn,
12292𓀴haggard old man, whose white hair matches well with the hollow burning
12293𓀴eyes and grief-written lines of his face. His energy is still intact; in
12294𓀴fact, he is like a living flame. This may yet be his salvation, for, if
12295𓀴all go well, it will tide him over the despairing period; he will then,
12296𓀴in a kind of way, wake again to the realities of life. Poor fellow, I
12297𓀴thought my own trouble was bad enough, but his----! The Professor knows
12298𓀴this well enough, and is doing his best to keep his mind active. What he
12299𓀴has been saying was, under the circumstances, of absorbing interest. So
12300𓀴well as I can remember, here it is:--
12301𓀴
12302𓀴“I have studied, over and over again since they came into my hands, all
12303𓀴the papers relating to this monster; and the more I have studied, the
12304𓀴greater seems the necessity to utterly stamp him out. All through there
12305𓀴are signs of his advance; not only of his power, but of his knowledge of
12306𓀴it. As I learned from the researches of my friend Arminus of Buda-Pesth,
12307𓀴he was in life a most wonderful man. Soldier, statesman, and
12308𓀴alchemist--which latter was the highest development of the
12309𓀴science-knowledge of his time. He had a mighty brain, a learning beyond
12310𓀴compare, and a heart that knew no fear and no remorse. He dared even to
12311𓀴attend the Scholomance, and there was no branch of knowledge of his time
12312𓀴that he did not essay. Well, in him the brain powers survived the
12313𓀴physical death; though it would seem that memory was not all complete.
12314𓀴In some faculties of mind he has been, and is, only a child; but he is
12315𓀴growing, and some things that were childish at the first are now of
12316𓀴man’s stature. He is experimenting, and doing it well; and if it had not
12317𓀴been that we have crossed his path he would be yet--he may be yet if we
12318𓀴fail--the father or furtherer of a new order of beings, whose road must
12319𓀴lead through Death, not Life.”
12320𓀴
12321𓀴Harker groaned and said, “And this is all arrayed against my darling!
12322𓀴But how is he experimenting? The knowledge may help us to defeat him!”
12323𓀴
12324𓀴“He has all along, since his coming, been trying his power, slowly but
12325𓀴surely; that big child-brain of his is working. Well for us, it is, as
12326𓀴yet, a child-brain; for had he dared, at the first, to attempt certain
12327𓀴things he would long ago have been beyond our power. However, he means
12328𓀴to succeed, and a man who has centuries before him can afford to wait
12329𓀴and to go slow. _Festina lente_ may well be his motto.”
12330𓀴
12331𓀴“I fail to understand,” said Harker wearily. “Oh, do be more plain to
12332𓀴me! Perhaps grief and trouble are dulling my brain.”
12333𓀴
12334𓀴The Professor laid his hand tenderly on his shoulder as he spoke:--
12335𓀴
12336𓀴“Ah, my child, I will be plain. Do you not see how, of late, this
12337𓀴monster has been creeping into knowledge experimentally. How he has been
12338𓀴making use of the zoöphagous patient to effect his entry into friend
12339𓀴John’s home; for your Vampire, though in all afterwards he can come when
12340𓀴and how he will, must at the first make entry only when asked thereto by
12341𓀴an inmate. But these are not his most important experiments. Do we not
12342𓀴see how at the first all these so great boxes were moved by others. He
12343𓀴knew not then but that must be so. But all the time that so great
12344𓀴child-brain of his was growing, and he began to consider whether he
12345𓀴might not himself move the box. So he began to help; and then, when he
12346𓀴found that this be all-right, he try to move them all alone. And so he
12347𓀴progress, and he scatter these graves of him; and none but he know where
12348𓀴they are hidden. He may have intend to bury them deep in the ground. So
12349𓀴that he only use them in the night, or at such time as he can change his
12350𓀴form, they do him equal well; and none may know these are his
12351𓀴hiding-place! But, my child, do not despair; this knowledge come to him
12352𓀴just too late! Already all of his lairs but one be sterilise as for him;
12353𓀴and before the sunset this shall be so. Then he have no place where he
12354𓀴can move and hide. I delayed this morning that so we might be sure. Is
12355𓀴there not more at stake for us than for him? Then why we not be even
12356𓀴more careful than him? By my clock it is one hour and already, if all be
12357𓀴well, friend Arthur and Quincey are on their way to us. To-day is our
12358𓀴day, and we must go sure, if slow, and lose no chance. See! there are
12359𓀴five of us when those absent ones return.”
12360𓀴
12361𓀴Whilst he was speaking we were startled by a knock at the hall door, the
12362𓀴double postman’s knock of the telegraph boy. We all moved out to the
12363𓀴hall with one impulse, and Van Helsing, holding up his hand to us to
12364𓀴keep silence, stepped to the door and opened it. The boy handed in a
12365𓀴despatch. The Professor closed the door again, and, after looking at the
12366𓀴direction, opened it and read aloud.
12367𓀴
12368𓀴“Look out for D. He has just now, 12:45, come from Carfax hurriedly and
12369𓀴hastened towards the South. He seems to be going the round and may want
12370𓀴to see you: Mina.”
12371𓀴
12372𓀴There was a pause, broken by Jonathan Harker’s voice:--
12373𓀴
12374𓀴“Now, God be thanked, we shall soon meet!” Van Helsing turned to him
12375𓀴quickly and said:--
12376𓀴
12377𓀴“God will act in His own way and time. Do not fear, and do not rejoice
12378𓀴as yet; for what we wish for at the moment may be our undoings.”
12379𓀴
12380𓀴“I care for nothing now,” he answered hotly, “except to wipe out this
12381𓀴brute from the face of creation. I would sell my soul to do it!”
12382𓀴
12383𓀴“Oh, hush, hush, my child!” said Van Helsing. “God does not purchase
12384𓀴souls in this wise; and the Devil, though he may purchase, does not keep
12385𓀴faith. But God is merciful and just, and knows your pain and your
12386𓀴devotion to that dear Madam Mina. Think you, how her pain would be
12387𓀴doubled, did she but hear your wild words. Do not fear any of us, we are
12388𓀴all devoted to this cause, and to-day shall see the end. The time is
12389𓀴coming for action; to-day this Vampire is limit to the powers of man,
12390𓀴and till sunset he may not change. It will take him time to arrive
12391𓀴here--see, it is twenty minutes past one--and there are yet some times
12392𓀴before he can hither come, be he never so quick. What we must hope for
12393𓀴is that my Lord Arthur and Quincey arrive first.”
12394𓀴
12395𓀴About half an hour after we had received Mrs. Harker’s telegram, there
12396𓀴came a quiet, resolute knock at the hall door. It was just an ordinary
12397𓀴knock, such as is given hourly by thousands of gentlemen, but it made
12398𓀴the Professor’s heart and mine beat loudly. We looked at each other, and
12399𓀴together moved out into the hall; we each held ready to use our various
12400𓀴armaments--the spiritual in the left hand, the mortal in the right. Van
12401𓀴Helsing pulled back the latch, and, holding the door half open, stood
12402𓀴back, having both hands ready for action. The gladness of our hearts
12403𓀴must have shown upon our faces when on the step, close to the door, we
12404𓀴saw Lord Godalming and Quincey Morris. They came quickly in and closed
12405𓀴the door behind them, the former saying, as they moved along the
12406𓀴hall:--
12407𓀴
12408𓀴“It is all right. We found both places; six boxes in each and we
12409𓀴destroyed them all!”
12410𓀴
12411𓀴“Destroyed?” asked the Professor.
12412𓀴
12413𓀴“For him!” We were silent for a minute, and then Quincey said:--
12414𓀴
12415𓀴“There’s nothing to do but to wait here. If, however, he doesn’t turn up
12416𓀴by five o’clock, we must start off; for it won’t do to leave Mrs. Harker
12417𓀴alone after sunset.”
12418𓀴
12419𓀴“He will be here before long now,” said Van Helsing, who had been
12420𓀴consulting his pocket-book. “_Nota bene_, in Madam’s telegram he went
12421𓀴south from Carfax, that means he went to cross the river, and he could
12422𓀴only do so at slack of tide, which should be something before one
12423𓀴o’clock. That he went south has a meaning for us. He is as yet only
12424𓀴suspicious; and he went from Carfax first to the place where he would
12425𓀴suspect interference least. You must have been at Bermondsey only a
12426𓀴short time before him. That he is not here already shows that he went to
12427𓀴Mile End next. This took him some time; for he would then have to be
12428𓀴carried over the river in some way. Believe me, my friends, we shall not
12429𓀴have long to wait now. We should have ready some plan of attack, so that
12430𓀴we may throw away no chance. Hush, there is no time now. Have all your
12431𓀴arms! Be ready!” He held up a warning hand as he spoke, for we all could
12432𓀴hear a key softly inserted in the lock of the hall door.
12433𓀴
12434𓀴I could not but admire, even at such a moment, the way in which a
12435𓀴dominant spirit asserted itself. In all our hunting parties and
12436𓀴adventures in different parts of the world, Quincey Morris had always
12437𓀴been the one to arrange the plan of action, and Arthur and I had been
12438𓀴accustomed to obey him implicitly. Now, the old habit seemed to be
12439𓀴renewed instinctively. With a swift glance around the room, he at once
12440𓀴laid out our plan of attack, and, without speaking a word, with a
12441𓀴gesture, placed us each in position. Van Helsing, Harker, and I were
12442𓀴just behind the door, so that when it was opened the Professor could
12443𓀴guard it whilst we two stepped between the incomer and the door.
12444𓀴Godalming behind and Quincey in front stood just out of sight ready to
12445𓀴move in front of the window. We waited in a suspense that made the
12446𓀴seconds pass with nightmare slowness. The slow, careful steps came along
12447𓀴the hall; the Count was evidently prepared for some surprise--at least
12448𓀴he feared it.
12449𓀴
12450𓀴Suddenly with a single bound he leaped into the room, winning a way past
12451𓀴us before any of us could raise a hand to stay him. There was something
12452𓀴so panther-like in the movement--something so unhuman, that it seemed
12453𓀴to sober us all from the shock of his coming. The first to act was
12454𓀴Harker, who, with a quick movement, threw himself before the door
12455𓀴leading into the room in the front of the house. As the Count saw us, a
12456𓀴horrible sort of snarl passed over his face, showing the eye-teeth long
12457𓀴and pointed; but the evil smile as quickly passed into a cold stare of
12458𓀴lion-like disdain. His expression again changed as, with a single
12459𓀴impulse, we all advanced upon him. It was a pity that we had not some
12460𓀴better organised plan of attack, for even at the moment I wondered what
12461𓀴we were to do. I did not myself know whether our lethal weapons would
12462𓀴avail us anything. Harker evidently meant to try the matter, for he had
12463𓀴ready his great Kukri knife and made a fierce and sudden cut at him. The
12464𓀴blow was a powerful one; only the diabolical quickness of the Count’s
12465𓀴leap back saved him. A second less and the trenchant blade had shorne
12466𓀴through his heart. As it was, the point just cut the cloth of his coat,
12467𓀴making a wide gap whence a bundle of bank-notes and a stream of gold
12468𓀴fell out. The expression of the Count’s face was so hellish, that for a
12469𓀴moment I feared for Harker, though I saw him throw the terrible knife
12470𓀴aloft again for another stroke. Instinctively I moved forward with a
12471𓀴protective impulse, holding the Crucifix and Wafer in my left hand. I
12472𓀴felt a mighty power fly along my arm; and it was without surprise that I
12473𓀴saw the monster cower back before a similar movement made spontaneously
12474𓀴by each one of us. It would be impossible to describe the expression of
12475𓀴hate and baffled malignity--of anger and hellish rage--which came over
12476𓀴the Count’s face. His waxen hue became greenish-yellow by the contrast
12477𓀴of his burning eyes, and the red scar on the forehead showed on the
12478𓀴pallid skin like a palpitating wound. The next instant, with a sinuous
12479𓀴dive he swept under Harker’s arm, ere his blow could fall, and, grasping
12480𓀴a handful of the money from the floor, dashed across the room, threw
12481𓀴himself at the window. Amid the crash and glitter of the falling glass,
12482𓀴he tumbled into the flagged area below. Through the sound of the
12483𓀴shivering glass I could hear the “ting” of the gold, as some of the
12484𓀴sovereigns fell on the flagging.
12485𓀴
12486𓀴We ran over and saw him spring unhurt from the ground. He, rushing up
12487𓀴the steps, crossed the flagged yard, and pushed open the stable door.
12488𓀴There he turned and spoke to us:--
12489𓀴
12490𓀴“You think to baffle me, you--with your pale faces all in a row, like
12491𓀴sheep in a butcher’s. You shall be sorry yet, each one of you! You think
12492𓀴you have left me without a place to rest; but I have more. My revenge is
12493𓀴just begun! I spread it over centuries, and time is on my side. Your
12494𓀴girls that you all love are mine already; and through them you and
12495𓀴others shall yet be mine--my creatures, to do my bidding and to be my
12496𓀴jackals when I want to feed. Bah!” With a contemptuous sneer, he passed
12497𓀴quickly through the door, and we heard the rusty bolt creak as he
12498𓀴fastened it behind him. A door beyond opened and shut. The first of us
12499𓀴to speak was the Professor, as, realising the difficulty of following
12500𓀴him through the stable, we moved toward the hall.
12501𓀴
12502𓀴“We have learnt something--much! Notwithstanding his brave words, he
12503𓀴fears us; he fear time, he fear want! For if not, why he hurry so? His
12504𓀴very tone betray him, or my ears deceive. Why take that money? You
12505𓀴follow quick. You are hunters of wild beast, and understand it so. For
12506𓀴me, I make sure that nothing here may be of use to him, if so that he
12507𓀴return.” As he spoke he put the money remaining into his pocket; took
12508𓀴the title-deeds in the bundle as Harker had left them, and swept the
12509𓀴remaining things into the open fireplace, where he set fire to them with
12510𓀴a match.
12511𓀴
12512𓀴Godalming and Morris had rushed out into the yard, and Harker had
12513𓀴lowered himself from the window to follow the Count. He had, however,
12514𓀴bolted the stable door; and by the time they had forced it open there
12515𓀴was no sign of him. Van Helsing and I tried to make inquiry at the back
12516𓀴of the house; but the mews was deserted and no one had seen him depart.
12517𓀴
12518𓀴It was now late in the afternoon, and sunset was not far off. We had to
12519𓀴recognise that our game was up; with heavy hearts we agreed with the
12520𓀴Professor when he said:--
12521𓀴
12522𓀴“Let us go back to Madam Mina--poor, poor dear Madam Mina. All we can do
12523𓀴just now is done; and we can there, at least, protect her. But we need
12524𓀴not despair. There is but one more earth-box, and we must try to find
12525𓀴it; when that is done all may yet be well.” I could see that he spoke as
12526𓀴bravely as he could to comfort Harker. The poor fellow was quite broken
12527𓀴down; now and again he gave a low groan which he could not suppress--he
12528𓀴was thinking of his wife.
12529𓀴
12530𓀴With sad hearts we came back to my house, where we found Mrs. Harker
12531𓀴waiting us, with an appearance of cheerfulness which did honour to her
12532𓀴bravery and unselfishness. When she saw our faces, her own became as
12533𓀴pale as death: for a second or two her eyes were closed as if she were
12534𓀴in secret prayer; and then she said cheerfully:--
12535𓀴
12536𓀴“I can never thank you all enough. Oh, my poor darling!” As she spoke,
12537𓀴she took her husband’s grey head in her hands and kissed it--“Lay your
12538𓀴poor head here and rest it. All will yet be well, dear! God will protect
12539𓀴us if He so will it in His good intent.” The poor fellow groaned. There
12540𓀴was no place for words in his sublime misery.
12541𓀴
12542𓀴We had a sort of perfunctory supper together, and I think it cheered us
12543𓀴all up somewhat. It was, perhaps, the mere animal heat of food to hungry
12544𓀴people--for none of us had eaten anything since breakfast--or the sense
12545𓀴of companionship may have helped us; but anyhow we were all less
12546𓀴miserable, and saw the morrow as not altogether without hope. True to
12547𓀴our promise, we told Mrs. Harker everything which had passed; and
12548𓀴although she grew snowy white at times when danger had seemed to
12549𓀴threaten her husband, and red at others when his devotion to her was
12550𓀴manifested, she listened bravely and with calmness. When we came to the
12551𓀴part where Harker had rushed at the Count so recklessly, she clung to
12552𓀴her husband’s arm, and held it tight as though her clinging could
12553𓀴protect him from any harm that might come. She said nothing, however,
12554𓀴till the narration was all done, and matters had been brought right up
12555𓀴to the present time. Then without letting go her husband’s hand she
12556𓀴stood up amongst us and spoke. Oh, that I could give any idea of the
12557𓀴scene; of that sweet, sweet, good, good woman in all the radiant beauty
12558𓀴of her youth and animation, with the red scar on her forehead, of which
12559𓀴she was conscious, and which we saw with grinding of our
12560𓀴teeth--remembering whence and how it came; her loving kindness against
12561𓀴our grim hate; her tender faith against all our fears and doubting; and
12562𓀴we, knowing that so far as symbols went, she with all her goodness and
12563𓀴purity and faith, was outcast from God.
12564𓀴
12565𓀴“Jonathan,” she said, and the word sounded like music on her lips it was
12566𓀴so full of love and tenderness, “Jonathan dear, and you all my true,
12567𓀴true friends, I want you to bear something in mind through all this
12568𓀴dreadful time. I know that you must fight--that you must destroy even as
12569𓀴you destroyed the false Lucy so that the true Lucy might live hereafter;
12570𓀴but it is not a work of hate. That poor soul who has wrought all this
12571𓀴misery is the saddest case of all. Just think what will be his joy when
12572𓀴he, too, is destroyed in his worser part that his better part may have
12573𓀴spiritual immortality. You must be pitiful to him, too, though it may
12574𓀴not hold your hands from his destruction.”
12575𓀴
12576𓀴As she spoke I could see her husband’s face darken and draw together, as
12577𓀴though the passion in him were shrivelling his being to its core.
12578𓀴Instinctively the clasp on his wife’s hand grew closer, till his
12579𓀴knuckles looked white. She did not flinch from the pain which I knew she
12580𓀴must have suffered, but looked at him with eyes that were more appealing
12581𓀴than ever. As she stopped speaking he leaped to his feet, almost tearing
12582𓀴his hand from hers as he spoke:--
12583𓀴
12584𓀴“May God give him into my hand just for long enough to destroy that
12585𓀴earthly life of him which we are aiming at. If beyond it I could send
12586𓀴his soul for ever and ever to burning hell I would do it!”
12587𓀴
12588𓀴“Oh, hush! oh, hush! in the name of the good God. Don’t say such things,
12589𓀴Jonathan, my husband; or you will crush me with fear and horror. Just
12590𓀴think, my dear--I have been thinking all this long, long day of it--that
12591𓀴... perhaps ... some day ... I, too, may need such pity; and that some
12592𓀴other like you--and with equal cause for anger--may deny it to me! Oh,
12593𓀴my husband! my husband, indeed I would have spared you such a thought
12594𓀴had there been another way; but I pray that God may not have treasured
12595𓀴your wild words, except as the heart-broken wail of a very loving and
12596𓀴sorely stricken man. Oh, God, let these poor white hairs go in evidence
12597𓀴of what he has suffered, who all his life has done no wrong, and on whom
12598𓀴so many sorrows have come.”
12599𓀴
12600𓀴We men were all in tears now. There was no resisting them, and we wept
12601𓀴openly. She wept, too, to see that her sweeter counsels had prevailed.
12602𓀴Her husband flung himself on his knees beside her, and putting his arms
12603𓀴round her, hid his face in the folds of her dress. Van Helsing beckoned
12604𓀴to us and we stole out of the room, leaving the two loving hearts alone
12605𓀴with their God.
12606𓀴
12607𓀴Before they retired the Professor fixed up the room against any coming
12608𓀴of the Vampire, and assured Mrs. Harker that she might rest in peace.
12609𓀴She tried to school herself to the belief, and, manifestly for her
12610𓀴husband’s sake, tried to seem content. It was a brave struggle; and was,
12611𓀴I think and believe, not without its reward. Van Helsing had placed at
12612𓀴hand a bell which either of them was to sound in case of any emergency.
12613𓀴When they had retired, Quincey, Godalming, and I arranged that we should
12614𓀴sit up, dividing the night between us, and watch over the safety of the
12615𓀴poor stricken lady. The first watch falls to Quincey, so the rest of us
12616𓀴shall be off to bed as soon as we can. Godalming has already turned in,
12617𓀴for his is the second watch. Now that my work is done I, too, shall go
12618𓀴to bed.
12619𓀴
12620𓀴
12621𓀴_Jonathan Harker’s Journal._
12622𓀴
12623𓀴_3-4 October, close to midnight._--I thought yesterday would never end.
12624𓀴There was over me a yearning for sleep, in some sort of blind belief
12625𓀴that to wake would be to find things changed, and that any change must
12626𓀴now be for the better. Before we parted, we discussed what our next step
12627𓀴was to be, but we could arrive at no result. All we knew was that one
12628𓀴earth-box remained, and that the Count alone knew where it was. If he
12629𓀴chooses to lie hidden, he may baffle us for years; and in the
12630𓀴meantime!--the thought is too horrible, I dare not think of it even now.
12631𓀴This I know: that if ever there was a woman who was all perfection, that
12632𓀴one is my poor wronged darling. I love her a thousand times more for her
12633𓀴sweet pity of last night, a pity that made my own hate of the monster
12634𓀴seem despicable. Surely God will not permit the world to be the poorer
12635𓀴by the loss of such a creature. This is hope to me. We are all drifting
12636𓀴reefwards now, and faith is our only anchor. Thank God! Mina is
12637𓀴sleeping, and sleeping without dreams. I fear what her dreams might be
12638𓀴like, with such terrible memories to ground them in. She has not been so
12639𓀴calm, within my seeing, since the sunset. Then, for a while, there came
12640𓀴over her face a repose which was like spring after the blasts of March.
12641𓀴I thought at the time that it was the softness of the red sunset on her
12642𓀴face, but somehow now I think it has a deeper meaning. I am not sleepy
12643𓀴myself, though I am weary--weary to death. However, I must try to sleep;
12644𓀴for there is to-morrow to think of, and there is no rest for me
12645𓀴until....
12646𓀴
12647𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
12648𓀴
12649𓀴_Later._--I must have fallen asleep, for I was awaked by Mina, who was
12650𓀴sitting up in bed, with a startled look on her face. I could see easily,
12651𓀴for we did not leave the room in darkness; she had placed a warning hand
12652𓀴over my mouth, and now she whispered in my ear:--
12653𓀴
12654𓀴“Hush! there is someone in the corridor!” I got up softly, and crossing
12655𓀴the room, gently opened the door.
12656𓀴
12657𓀴Just outside, stretched on a mattress, lay Mr. Morris, wide awake. He
12658𓀴raised a warning hand for silence as he whispered to me:--
12659𓀴
12660𓀴“Hush! go back to bed; it is all right. One of us will be here all
12661𓀴night. We don’t mean to take any chances!”
12662𓀴
12663𓀴His look and gesture forbade discussion, so I came back and told Mina.
12664𓀴She sighed and positively a shadow of a smile stole over her poor, pale
12665𓀴face as she put her arms round me and said softly:--
12666𓀴
12667𓀴“Oh, thank God for good brave men!” With a sigh she sank back again to
12668𓀴sleep. I write this now as I am not sleepy, though I must try again.
12669𓀴
12670𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
12671𓀴
12672𓀴_4 October, morning._--Once again during the night I was wakened by
12673𓀴Mina. This time we had all had a good sleep, for the grey of the coming
12674𓀴dawn was making the windows into sharp oblongs, and the gas flame was
12675𓀴like a speck rather than a disc of light. She said to me hurriedly:--
12676𓀴
12677𓀴“Go, call the Professor. I want to see him at once.”
12678𓀴
12679𓀴“Why?” I asked.
12680𓀴
12681𓀴“I have an idea. I suppose it must have come in the night, and matured
12682𓀴without my knowing it. He must hypnotise me before the dawn, and then I
12683𓀴shall be able to speak. Go quick, dearest; the time is getting close.” I
12684𓀴went to the door. Dr. Seward was resting on the mattress, and, seeing
12685𓀴me, he sprang to his feet.
12686𓀴
12687𓀴“Is anything wrong?” he asked, in alarm.
12688𓀴
12689𓀴“No,” I replied; “but Mina wants to see Dr. Van Helsing at once.”
12690𓀴
12691𓀴“I will go,” he said, and hurried into the Professor’s room.
12692𓀴
12693𓀴In two or three minutes later Van Helsing was in the room in his
12694𓀴dressing-gown, and Mr. Morris and Lord Godalming were with Dr. Seward at
12695𓀴the door asking questions. When the Professor saw Mina smile--a
12696𓀴positive smile ousted the anxiety of his face; he rubbed his hands as he
12697𓀴said:--
12698𓀴
12699𓀴“Oh, my dear Madam Mina, this is indeed a change. See! friend Jonathan,
12700𓀴we have got our dear Madam Mina, as of old, back to us to-day!” Then
12701𓀴turning to her, he said, cheerfully: “And what am I do for you? For at
12702𓀴this hour you do not want me for nothings.”
12703𓀴
12704𓀴“I want you to hypnotise me!” she said. “Do it before the dawn, for I
12705𓀴feel that then I can speak, and speak freely. Be quick, for the time is
12706𓀴short!” Without a word he motioned her to sit up in bed.
12707𓀴
12708𓀴Looking fixedly at her, he commenced to make passes in front of her,
12709𓀴from over the top of her head downward, with each hand in turn. Mina
12710𓀴gazed at him fixedly for a few minutes, during which my own heart beat
12711𓀴like a trip hammer, for I felt that some crisis was at hand. Gradually
12712𓀴her eyes closed, and she sat, stock still; only by the gentle heaving of
12713𓀴her bosom could one know that she was alive. The Professor made a few
12714𓀴more passes and then stopped, and I could see that his forehead was
12715𓀴covered with great beads of perspiration. Mina opened her eyes; but she
12716𓀴did not seem the same woman. There was a far-away look in her eyes, and
12717𓀴her voice had a sad dreaminess which was new to me. Raising his hand to
12718𓀴impose silence, the Professor motioned to me to bring the others in.
12719𓀴They came on tip-toe, closing the door behind them, and stood at the
12720𓀴foot of the bed, looking on. Mina appeared not to see them. The
12721𓀴stillness was broken by Van Helsing’s voice speaking in a low level tone
12722𓀴which would not break the current of her thoughts:--
12723𓀴
12724𓀴“Where are you?” The answer came in a neutral way:--
12725𓀴
12726𓀴“I do not know. Sleep has no place it can call its own.” For several
12727𓀴minutes there was silence. Mina sat rigid, and the Professor stood
12728𓀴staring at her fixedly; the rest of us hardly dared to breathe. The room
12729𓀴was growing lighter; without taking his eyes from Mina’s face, Dr. Van
12730𓀴Helsing motioned me to pull up the blind. I did so, and the day seemed
12731𓀴just upon us. A red streak shot up, and a rosy light seemed to diffuse
12732𓀴itself through the room. On the instant the Professor spoke again:--
12733𓀴
12734𓀴“Where are you now?” The answer came dreamily, but with intention; it
12735𓀴were as though she were interpreting something. I have heard her use the
12736𓀴same tone when reading her shorthand notes.
12737𓀴
12738𓀴“I do not know. It is all strange to me!”
12739𓀴
12740𓀴“What do you see?”
12741𓀴
12742𓀴“I can see nothing; it is all dark.”
12743𓀴
12744𓀴“What do you hear?” I could detect the strain in the Professor’s patient
12745𓀴voice.
12746𓀴
12747𓀴“The lapping of water. It is gurgling by, and little waves leap. I can
12748𓀴hear them on the outside.”
12749𓀴
12750𓀴“Then you are on a ship?” We all looked at each other, trying to glean
12751𓀴something each from the other. We were afraid to think. The answer came
12752𓀴quick:--
12753𓀴
12754𓀴“Oh, yes!”
12755𓀴
12756𓀴“What else do you hear?”
12757𓀴
12758𓀴“The sound of men stamping overhead as they run about. There is the
12759𓀴creaking of a chain, and the loud tinkle as the check of the capstan
12760𓀴falls into the rachet.”
12761𓀴
12762𓀴“What are you doing?”
12763𓀴
12764𓀴“I am still--oh, so still. It is like death!” The voice faded away into
12765𓀴a deep breath as of one sleeping, and the open eyes closed again.
12766𓀴
12767𓀴By this time the sun had risen, and we were all in the full light of
12768𓀴day. Dr. Van Helsing placed his hands on Mina’s shoulders, and laid her
12769𓀴head down softly on her pillow. She lay like a sleeping child for a few
12770𓀴moments, and then, with a long sigh, awoke and stared in wonder to see
12771𓀴us all around her. “Have I been talking in my sleep?” was all she said.
12772𓀴She seemed, however, to know the situation without telling, though she
12773𓀴was eager to know what she had told. The Professor repeated the
12774𓀴conversation, and she said:--
12775𓀴
12776𓀴“Then there is not a moment to lose: it may not be yet too late!” Mr.
12777𓀴Morris and Lord Godalming started for the door but the Professor’s calm
12778𓀴voice called them back:--
12779𓀴
12780𓀴“Stay, my friends. That ship, wherever it was, was weighing anchor
12781𓀴whilst she spoke. There are many ships weighing anchor at the moment in
12782𓀴your so great Port of London. Which of them is it that you seek? God be
12783𓀴thanked that we have once again a clue, though whither it may lead us we
12784𓀴know not. We have been blind somewhat; blind after the manner of men,
12785𓀴since when we can look back we see what we might have seen looking
12786𓀴forward if we had been able to see what we might have seen! Alas, but
12787𓀴that sentence is a puddle; is it not? We can know now what was in the
12788𓀴Count’s mind, when he seize that money, though Jonathan’s so fierce
12789𓀴knife put him in the danger that even he dread. He meant escape. Hear
12790𓀴me, ESCAPE! He saw that with but one earth-box left, and a pack of men
12791𓀴following like dogs after a fox, this London was no place for him. He
12792𓀴have take his last earth-box on board a ship, and he leave the land. He
12793𓀴think to escape, but no! we follow him. Tally Ho! as friend Arthur would
12794𓀴say when he put on his red frock! Our old fox is wily; oh! so wily, and
12795𓀴we must follow with wile. I, too, am wily and I think his mind in a
12796𓀴little while. In meantime we may rest and in peace, for there are waters
12797𓀴between us which he do not want to pass, and which he could not if he
12798𓀴would--unless the ship were to touch the land, and then only at full or
12799𓀴slack tide. See, and the sun is just rose, and all day to sunset is to
12800𓀴us. Let us take bath, and dress, and have breakfast which we all need,
12801𓀴and which we can eat comfortably since he be not in the same land with
12802𓀴us.” Mina looked at him appealingly as she asked:--
12803𓀴
12804𓀴“But why need we seek him further, when he is gone away from us?” He
12805𓀴took her hand and patted it as he replied:--
12806𓀴
12807𓀴“Ask me nothings as yet. When we have breakfast, then I answer all
12808𓀴questions.” He would say no more, and we separated to dress.
12809𓀴
12810𓀴After breakfast Mina repeated her question. He looked at her gravely for
12811𓀴a minute and then said sorrowfully:--
12812𓀴
12813𓀴“Because my dear, dear Madam Mina, now more than ever must we find him
12814𓀴even if we have to follow him to the jaws of Hell!” She grew paler as
12815𓀴she asked faintly:--
12816𓀴
12817𓀴“Why?”
12818𓀴
12819𓀴“Because,” he answered solemnly, “he can live for centuries, and you are
12820𓀴but mortal woman. Time is now to be dreaded--since once he put that mark
12821𓀴upon your throat.”
12822𓀴
12823𓀴I was just in time to catch her as she fell forward in a faint.
12824𓀴
12825𓀴
12826𓀴
12827𓀴
12828𓀴CHAPTER XXIV
12829𓀴
12830𓀴DR. SEWARD’S PHONOGRAPH DIARY, SPOKEN BY VAN HELSING
12831𓀴
12832𓀴
12833𓀴This to Jonathan Harker.
12834𓀴
12835𓀴You are to stay with your dear Madam Mina. We shall go to make our
12836𓀴search--if I can call it so, for it is not search but knowing, and we
12837𓀴seek confirmation only. But do you stay and take care of her to-day.
12838𓀴This is your best and most holiest office. This day nothing can find him
12839𓀴here. Let me tell you that so you will know what we four know already,
12840𓀴for I have tell them. He, our enemy, have gone away; he have gone back
12841𓀴to his Castle in Transylvania. I know it so well, as if a great hand of
12842𓀴fire wrote it on the wall. He have prepare for this in some way, and
12843𓀴that last earth-box was ready to ship somewheres. For this he took the
12844𓀴money; for this he hurry at the last, lest we catch him before the sun
12845𓀴go down. It was his last hope, save that he might hide in the tomb that
12846𓀴he think poor Miss Lucy, being as he thought like him, keep open to him.
12847𓀴But there was not of time. When that fail he make straight for his last
12848𓀴resource--his last earth-work I might say did I wish _double entente_.
12849𓀴He is clever, oh, so clever! he know that his game here was finish; and
12850𓀴so he decide he go back home. He find ship going by the route he came,
12851𓀴and he go in it. We go off now to find what ship, and whither bound;
12852𓀴when we have discover that, we come back and tell you all. Then we will
12853𓀴comfort you and poor dear Madam Mina with new hope. For it will be hope
12854𓀴when you think it over: that all is not lost. This very creature that we
12855𓀴pursue, he take hundreds of years to get so far as London; and yet in
12856𓀴one day, when we know of the disposal of him we drive him out. He is
12857𓀴finite, though he is powerful to do much harm and suffers not as we do.
12858𓀴But we are strong, each in our purpose; and we are all more strong
12859𓀴together. Take heart afresh, dear husband of Madam Mina. This battle is
12860𓀴but begun, and in the end we shall win--so sure as that God sits on high
12861𓀴to watch over His children. Therefore be of much comfort till we return.
12862𓀴
12863𓀴VAN HELSING.
12864𓀴
12865𓀴
12866𓀴_Jonathan Harker’s Journal._
12867𓀴
12868𓀴_4 October._--When I read to Mina, Van Helsing’s message in the
12869𓀴phonograph, the poor girl brightened up considerably. Already the
12870𓀴certainty that the Count is out of the country has given her comfort;
12871𓀴and comfort is strength to her. For my own part, now that his horrible
12872𓀴danger is not face to face with us, it seems almost impossible to
12873𓀴believe in it. Even my own terrible experiences in Castle Dracula seem
12874𓀴like a long-forgotten dream. Here in the crisp autumn air in the bright
12875𓀴sunlight----
12876𓀴
12877𓀴Alas! how can I disbelieve! In the midst of my thought my eye fell on
12878𓀴the red scar on my poor darling’s white forehead. Whilst that lasts,
12879𓀴there can be no disbelief. And afterwards the very memory of it will
12880𓀴keep faith crystal clear. Mina and I fear to be idle, so we have been
12881𓀴over all the diaries again and again. Somehow, although the reality
12882𓀴seems greater each time, the pain and the fear seem less. There is
12883𓀴something of a guiding purpose manifest throughout, which is comforting.
12884𓀴Mina says that perhaps we are the instruments of ultimate good. It may
12885𓀴be! I shall try to think as she does. We have never spoken to each other
12886𓀴yet of the future. It is better to wait till we see the Professor and
12887𓀴the others after their investigations.
12888𓀴
12889𓀴The day is running by more quickly than I ever thought a day could run
12890𓀴for me again. It is now three o’clock.
12891𓀴
12892𓀴
12893𓀴_Mina Harker’s Journal._
12894𓀴
12895𓀴_5 October, 5 p. m._--Our meeting for report. Present: Professor Van
12896𓀴Helsing, Lord Godalming, Dr. Seward, Mr. Quincey Morris, Jonathan
12897𓀴Harker, Mina Harker.
12898𓀴
12899𓀴Dr. Van Helsing described what steps were taken during the day to
12900𓀴discover on what boat and whither bound Count Dracula made his escape:--
12901𓀴
12902𓀴“As I knew that he wanted to get back to Transylvania, I felt sure that
12903𓀴he must go by the Danube mouth; or by somewhere in the Black Sea, since
12904𓀴by that way he come. It was a dreary blank that was before us. _Omne
12905𓀴ignotum pro magnifico_; and so with heavy hearts we start to find what
12906𓀴ships leave for the Black Sea last night. He was in sailing ship, since
12907𓀴Madam Mina tell of sails being set. These not so important as to go in
12908𓀴your list of the shipping in the _Times_, and so we go, by suggestion of
12909𓀴Lord Godalming, to your Lloyd’s, where are note of all ships that sail,
12910𓀴however so small. There we find that only one Black-Sea-bound ship go
12911𓀴out with the tide. She is the _Czarina Catherine_, and she sail from
12912𓀴Doolittle’s Wharf for Varna, and thence on to other parts and up the
12913𓀴Danube. ‘Soh!’ said I, ‘this is the ship whereon is the Count.’ So off
12914𓀴we go to Doolittle’s Wharf, and there we find a man in an office of wood
12915𓀴so small that the man look bigger than the office. From him we inquire
12916𓀴of the goings of the _Czarina Catherine_. He swear much, and he red face
12917𓀴and loud of voice, but he good fellow all the same; and when Quincey
12918𓀴give him something from his pocket which crackle as he roll it up, and
12919𓀴put it in a so small bag which he have hid deep in his clothing, he
12920𓀴still better fellow and humble servant to us. He come with us, and ask
12921𓀴many men who are rough and hot; these be better fellows too when they
12922𓀴have been no more thirsty. They say much of blood and bloom, and of
12923𓀴others which I comprehend not, though I guess what they mean; but
12924𓀴nevertheless they tell us all things which we want to know.
12925𓀴
12926𓀴“They make known to us among them, how last afternoon at about five
12927𓀴o’clock comes a man so hurry. A tall man, thin and pale, with high nose
12928𓀴and teeth so white, and eyes that seem to be burning. That he be all in
12929𓀴black, except that he have a hat of straw which suit not him or the
12930𓀴time. That he scatter his money in making quick inquiry as to what ship
12931𓀴sails for the Black Sea and for where. Some took him to the office and
12932𓀴then to the ship, where he will not go aboard but halt at shore end of
12933𓀴gang-plank, and ask that the captain come to him. The captain come, when
12934𓀴told that he will be pay well; and though he swear much at the first he
12935𓀴agree to term. Then the thin man go and some one tell him where horse
12936𓀴and cart can be hired. He go there and soon he come again, himself
12937𓀴driving cart on which a great box; this he himself lift down, though it
12938𓀴take several to put it on truck for the ship. He give much talk to
12939𓀴captain as to how and where his box is to be place; but the captain like
12940𓀴it not and swear at him in many tongues, and tell him that if he like he
12941𓀴can come and see where it shall be. But he say ‘no’; that he come not
12942𓀴yet, for that he have much to do. Whereupon the captain tell him that he
12943𓀴had better be quick--with blood--for that his ship will leave the
12944𓀴place--of blood--before the turn of the tide--with blood. Then the thin
12945𓀴man smile and say that of course he must go when he think fit; but he
12946𓀴will be surprise if he go quite so soon. The captain swear again,
12947𓀴polyglot, and the thin man make him bow, and thank him, and say that he
12948𓀴will so far intrude on his kindness as to come aboard before the
12949𓀴sailing. Final the captain, more red than ever, and in more tongues tell
12950𓀴him that he doesn’t want no Frenchmen--with bloom upon them and also
12951𓀴with blood--in his ship--with blood on her also. And so, after asking
12952𓀴where there might be close at hand a ship where he might purchase ship
12953𓀴forms, he departed.
12954𓀴
12955𓀴“No one knew where he went ‘or bloomin’ well cared,’ as they said, for
12956𓀴they had something else to think of--well with blood again; for it soon
12957𓀴became apparent to all that the _Czarina Catherine_ would not sail as
12958𓀴was expected. A thin mist began to creep up from the river, and it grew,
12959𓀴and grew; till soon a dense fog enveloped the ship and all around her.
12960𓀴The captain swore polyglot--very polyglot--polyglot with bloom and
12961𓀴blood; but he could do nothing. The water rose and rose; and he began to
12962𓀴fear that he would lose the tide altogether. He was in no friendly mood,
12963𓀴when just at full tide, the thin man came up the gang-plank again and
12964𓀴asked to see where his box had been stowed. Then the captain replied
12965𓀴that he wished that he and his box--old and with much bloom and
12966𓀴blood--were in hell. But the thin man did not be offend, and went down
12967𓀴with the mate and saw where it was place, and came up and stood awhile
12968𓀴on deck in fog. He must have come off by himself, for none notice him.
12969𓀴Indeed they thought not of him; for soon the fog begin to melt away, and
12970𓀴all was clear again. My friends of the thirst and the language that was
12971𓀴of bloom and blood laughed, as they told how the captain’s swears
12972𓀴exceeded even his usual polyglot, and was more than ever full of
12973𓀴picturesque, when on questioning other mariners who were on movement up
12974𓀴and down on the river that hour, he found that few of them had seen any
12975𓀴of fog at all, except where it lay round the wharf. However, the ship
12976𓀴went out on the ebb tide; and was doubtless by morning far down the
12977𓀴river mouth. She was by then, when they told us, well out to sea.
12978𓀴
12979𓀴“And so, my dear Madam Mina, it is that we have to rest for a time, for
12980𓀴our enemy is on the sea, with the fog at his command, on his way to the
12981𓀴Danube mouth. To sail a ship takes time, go she never so quick; and when
12982𓀴we start we go on land more quick, and we meet him there. Our best hope
12983𓀴is to come on him when in the box between sunrise and sunset; for then
12984𓀴he can make no struggle, and we may deal with him as we should. There
12985𓀴are days for us, in which we can make ready our plan. We know all about
12986𓀴where he go; for we have seen the owner of the ship, who have shown us
12987𓀴invoices and all papers that can be. The box we seek is to be landed in
12988𓀴Varna, and to be given to an agent, one Ristics who will there present
12989𓀴his credentials; and so our merchant friend will have done his part.
12990𓀴When he ask if there be any wrong, for that so, he can telegraph and
12991𓀴have inquiry made at Varna, we say ‘no’; for what is to be done is not
12992𓀴for police or of the customs. It must be done by us alone and in our own
12993𓀴way.”
12994𓀴
12995𓀴When Dr. Van Helsing had done speaking, I asked him if he were certain
12996𓀴that the Count had remained on board the ship. He replied: “We have the
12997𓀴best proof of that: your own evidence, when in the hypnotic trance this
12998𓀴morning.” I asked him again if it were really necessary that they should
12999𓀴pursue the Count, for oh! I dread Jonathan leaving me, and I know that
13000𓀴he would surely go if the others went. He answered in growing passion,
13001𓀴at first quietly. As he went on, however, he grew more angry and more
13002𓀴forceful, till in the end we could not but see wherein was at least some
13003𓀴of that personal dominance which made him so long a master amongst
13004𓀴men:--
13005𓀴
13006𓀴“Yes, it is necessary--necessary--necessary! For your sake in the first,
13007𓀴and then for the sake of humanity. This monster has done much harm
13008𓀴already, in the narrow scope where he find himself, and in the short
13009𓀴time when as yet he was only as a body groping his so small measure in
13010𓀴darkness and not knowing. All this have I told these others; you, my
13011𓀴dear Madam Mina, will learn it in the phonograph of my friend John, or
13012𓀴in that of your husband. I have told them how the measure of leaving his
13013𓀴own barren land--barren of peoples--and coming to a new land where life
13014𓀴of man teems till they are like the multitude of standing corn, was the
13015𓀴work of centuries. Were another of the Un-Dead, like him, to try to do
13016𓀴what he has done, perhaps not all the centuries of the world that have
13017𓀴been, or that will be, could aid him. With this one, all the forces of
13018𓀴nature that are occult and deep and strong must have worked together in
13019𓀴some wondrous way. The very place, where he have been alive, Un-Dead for
13020𓀴all these centuries, is full of strangeness of the geologic and chemical
13021𓀴world. There are deep caverns and fissures that reach none know whither.
13022𓀴There have been volcanoes, some of whose openings still send out waters
13023𓀴of strange properties, and gases that kill or make to vivify. Doubtless,
13024𓀴there is something magnetic or electric in some of these combinations of
13025𓀴occult forces which work for physical life in strange way; and in
13026𓀴himself were from the first some great qualities. In a hard and warlike
13027𓀴time he was celebrate that he have more iron nerve, more subtle brain,
13028𓀴more braver heart, than any man. In him some vital principle have in
13029𓀴strange way found their utmost; and as his body keep strong and grow and
13030𓀴thrive, so his brain grow too. All this without that diabolic aid which
13031𓀴is surely to him; for it have to yield to the powers that come from,
13032𓀴and are, symbolic of good. And now this is what he is to us. He have
13033𓀴infect you--oh, forgive me, my dear, that I must say such; but it is for
13034𓀴good of you that I speak. He infect you in such wise, that even if he do
13035𓀴no more, you have only to live--to live in your own old, sweet way; and
13036𓀴so in time, death, which is of man’s common lot and with God’s sanction,
13037𓀴shall make you like to him. This must not be! We have sworn together
13038𓀴that it must not. Thus are we ministers of God’s own wish: that the
13039𓀴world, and men for whom His Son die, will not be given over to monsters,
13040𓀴whose very existence would defame Him. He have allowed us to redeem one
13041𓀴soul already, and we go out as the old knights of the Cross to redeem
13042𓀴more. Like them we shall travel towards the sunrise; and like them, if
13043𓀴we fall, we fall in good cause.” He paused and I said:--
13044𓀴
13045𓀴“But will not the Count take his rebuff wisely? Since he has been driven
13046𓀴from England, will he not avoid it, as a tiger does the village from
13047𓀴which he has been hunted?”
13048𓀴
13049𓀴“Aha!” he said, “your simile of the tiger good, for me, and I shall
13050𓀴adopt him. Your man-eater, as they of India call the tiger who has once
13051𓀴tasted blood of the human, care no more for the other prey, but prowl
13052𓀴unceasing till he get him. This that we hunt from our village is a
13053𓀴tiger, too, a man-eater, and he never cease to prowl. Nay, in himself he
13054𓀴is not one to retire and stay afar. In his life, his living life, he go
13055𓀴over the Turkey frontier and attack his enemy on his own ground; he be
13056𓀴beaten back, but did he stay? No! He come again, and again, and again.
13057𓀴Look at his persistence and endurance. With the child-brain that was to
13058𓀴him he have long since conceive the idea of coming to a great city. What
13059𓀴does he do? He find out the place of all the world most of promise for
13060𓀴him. Then he deliberately set himself down to prepare for the task. He
13061𓀴find in patience just how is his strength, and what are his powers. He
13062𓀴study new tongues. He learn new social life; new environment of old
13063𓀴ways, the politic, the law, the finance, the science, the habit of a new
13064𓀴land and a new people who have come to be since he was. His glimpse that
13065𓀴he have had, whet his appetite only and enkeen his desire. Nay, it help
13066𓀴him to grow as to his brain; for it all prove to him how right he was at
13067𓀴the first in his surmises. He have done this alone; all alone! from a
13068𓀴ruin tomb in a forgotten land. What more may he not do when the greater
13069𓀴world of thought is open to him. He that can smile at death, as we know
13070𓀴him; who can flourish in the midst of diseases that kill off whole
13071𓀴peoples. Oh, if such an one was to come from God, and not the Devil,
13072𓀴what a force for good might he not be in this old world of ours. But we
13073𓀴are pledged to set the world free. Our toil must be in silence, and our
13074𓀴efforts all in secret; for in this enlightened age, when men believe not
13075𓀴even what they see, the doubting of wise men would be his greatest
13076𓀴strength. It would be at once his sheath and his armour, and his weapons
13077𓀴to destroy us, his enemies, who are willing to peril even our own souls
13078𓀴for the safety of one we love--for the good of mankind, and for the
13079𓀴honour and glory of God.”
13080𓀴
13081𓀴After a general discussion it was determined that for to-night nothing
13082𓀴be definitely settled; that we should all sleep on the facts, and try to
13083𓀴think out the proper conclusions. To-morrow, at breakfast, we are to
13084𓀴meet again, and, after making our conclusions known to one another, we
13085𓀴shall decide on some definite cause of action.
13086𓀴
13087𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
13088𓀴
13089𓀴I feel a wonderful peace and rest to-night. It is as if some haunting
13090𓀴presence were removed from me. Perhaps ...
13091𓀴
13092𓀴My surmise was not finished, could not be; for I caught sight in the
13093𓀴mirror of the red mark upon my forehead; and I knew that I was still
13094𓀴unclean.
13095𓀴
13096𓀴
13097𓀴_Dr. Seward’s Diary._
13098𓀴
13099𓀴_5 October._--We all rose early, and I think that sleep did much for
13100𓀴each and all of us. When we met at early breakfast there was more
13101𓀴general cheerfulness than any of us had ever expected to experience
13102𓀴again.
13103𓀴
13104𓀴It is really wonderful how much resilience there is in human nature. Let
13105𓀴any obstructing cause, no matter what, be removed in any way--even by
13106𓀴death--and we fly back to first principles of hope and enjoyment. More
13107𓀴than once as we sat around the table, my eyes opened in wonder whether
13108𓀴the whole of the past days had not been a dream. It was only when I
13109𓀴caught sight of the red blotch on Mrs. Harker’s forehead that I was
13110𓀴brought back to reality. Even now, when I am gravely revolving the
13111𓀴matter, it is almost impossible to realise that the cause of all our
13112𓀴trouble is still existent. Even Mrs. Harker seems to lose sight of her
13113𓀴trouble for whole spells; it is only now and again, when something
13114𓀴recalls it to her mind, that she thinks of her terrible scar. We are to
13115𓀴meet here in my study in half an hour and decide on our course of
13116𓀴action. I see only one immediate difficulty, I know it by instinct
13117𓀴rather than reason: we shall all have to speak frankly; and yet I fear
13118𓀴that in some mysterious way poor Mrs. Harker’s tongue is tied. I _know_
13119𓀴that she forms conclusions of her own, and from all that has been I can
13120𓀴guess how brilliant and how true they must be; but she will not, or
13121𓀴cannot, give them utterance. I have mentioned this to Van Helsing, and
13122𓀴he and I are to talk it over when we are alone. I suppose it is some of
13123𓀴that horrid poison which has got into her veins beginning to work. The
13124𓀴Count had his own purposes when he gave her what Van Helsing called “the
13125𓀴Vampire’s baptism of blood.” Well, there may be a poison that distils
13126𓀴itself out of good things; in an age when the existence of ptomaines is
13127𓀴a mystery we should not wonder at anything! One thing I know: that if my
13128𓀴instinct be true regarding poor Mrs. Harker’s silences, then there is a
13129𓀴terrible difficulty--an unknown danger--in the work before us. The same
13130𓀴power that compels her silence may compel her speech. I dare not think
13131𓀴further; for so I should in my thoughts dishonour a noble woman!
13132𓀴
13133𓀴Van Helsing is coming to my study a little before the others. I shall
13134𓀴try to open the subject with him.
13135𓀴
13136𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
13137𓀴
13138𓀴_Later._--When the Professor came in, we talked over the state of
13139𓀴things. I could see that he had something on his mind which he wanted to
13140𓀴say, but felt some hesitancy about broaching the subject. After beating
13141𓀴about the bush a little, he said suddenly:--
13142𓀴
13143𓀴“Friend John, there is something that you and I must talk of alone, just
13144𓀴at the first at any rate. Later, we may have to take the others into our
13145𓀴confidence”; then he stopped, so I waited; he went on:--
13146𓀴
13147𓀴“Madam Mina, our poor, dear Madam Mina is changing.” A cold shiver ran
13148𓀴through me to find my worst fears thus endorsed. Van Helsing
13149𓀴continued:--
13150𓀴
13151𓀴“With the sad experience of Miss Lucy, we must this time be warned
13152𓀴before things go too far. Our task is now in reality more difficult than
13153𓀴ever, and this new trouble makes every hour of the direst importance. I
13154𓀴can see the characteristics of the vampire coming in her face. It is now
13155𓀴but very, very slight; but it is to be seen if we have eyes to notice
13156𓀴without to prejudge. Her teeth are some sharper, and at times her eyes
13157𓀴are more hard. But these are not all, there is to her the silence now
13158𓀴often; as so it was with Miss Lucy. She did not speak, even when she
13159𓀴wrote that which she wished to be known later. Now my fear is this. If
13160𓀴it be that she can, by our hypnotic trance, tell what the Count see and
13161𓀴hear, is it not more true that he who have hypnotise her first, and who
13162𓀴have drink of her very blood and make her drink of his, should, if he
13163𓀴will, compel her mind to disclose to him that which she know?” I nodded
13164𓀴acquiescence; he went on:--
13165𓀴
13166𓀴“Then, what we must do is to prevent this; we must keep her ignorant of
13167𓀴our intent, and so she cannot tell what she know not. This is a painful
13168𓀴task! Oh, so painful that it heart-break me to think of; but it must be.
13169𓀴When to-day we meet, I must tell her that for reason which we will not
13170𓀴to speak she must not more be of our council, but be simply guarded by
13171𓀴us.” He wiped his forehead, which had broken out in profuse perspiration
13172𓀴at the thought of the pain which he might have to inflict upon the poor
13173𓀴soul already so tortured. I knew that it would be some sort of comfort
13174𓀴to him if I told him that I also had come to the same conclusion; for at
13175𓀴any rate it would take away the pain of doubt. I told him, and the
13176𓀴effect was as I expected.
13177𓀴
13178𓀴It is now close to the time of our general gathering. Van Helsing has
13179𓀴gone away to prepare for the meeting, and his painful part of it. I
13180𓀴really believe his purpose is to be able to pray alone.
13181𓀴
13182𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
13183𓀴
13184𓀴_Later._--At the very outset of our meeting a great personal relief was
13185𓀴experienced by both Van Helsing and myself. Mrs. Harker had sent a
13186𓀴message by her husband to say that she would not join us at present, as
13187𓀴she thought it better that we should be free to discuss our movements
13188𓀴without her presence to embarrass us. The Professor and I looked at each
13189𓀴other for an instant, and somehow we both seemed relieved. For my own
13190𓀴part, I thought that if Mrs. Harker realised the danger herself, it was
13191𓀴much pain as well as much danger averted. Under the circumstances we
13192𓀴agreed, by a questioning look and answer, with finger on lip, to
13193𓀴preserve silence in our suspicions, until we should have been able to
13194𓀴confer alone again. We went at once into our Plan of Campaign. Van
13195𓀴Helsing roughly put the facts before us first:--
13196𓀴
13197𓀴“The _Czarina Catherine_ left the Thames yesterday morning. It will take
13198𓀴her at the quickest speed she has ever made at least three weeks to
13199𓀴reach Varna; but we can travel overland to the same place in three days.
13200𓀴Now, if we allow for two days less for the ship’s voyage, owing to such
13201𓀴weather influences as we know that the Count can bring to bear; and if
13202𓀴we allow a whole day and night for any delays which may occur to us,
13203𓀴then we have a margin of nearly two weeks. Thus, in order to be quite
13204𓀴safe, we must leave here on 17th at latest. Then we shall at any rate
13205𓀴be in Varna a day before the ship arrives, and able to make such
13206𓀴preparations as may be necessary. Of course we shall all go armed--armed
13207𓀴against evil things, spiritual as well as physical.” Here Quincey Morris
13208𓀴added:--
13209𓀴
13210𓀴“I understand that the Count comes from a wolf country, and it may be
13211𓀴that he shall get there before us. I propose that we add Winchesters to
13212𓀴our armament. I have a kind of belief in a Winchester when there is any
13213𓀴trouble of that sort around. Do you remember, Art, when we had the pack
13214𓀴after us at Tobolsk? What wouldn’t we have given then for a repeater
13215𓀴apiece!”
13216𓀴
13217𓀴“Good!” said Van Helsing, “Winchesters it shall be. Quincey’s head is
13218𓀴level at all times, but most so when there is to hunt, metaphor be more
13219𓀴dishonour to science than wolves be of danger to man. In the meantime we
13220𓀴can do nothing here; and as I think that Varna is not familiar to any of
13221𓀴us, why not go there more soon? It is as long to wait here as there.
13222𓀴To-night and to-morrow we can get ready, and then, if all be well, we
13223𓀴four can set out on our journey.”
13224𓀴
13225𓀴“We four?” said Harker interrogatively, looking from one to another of
13226𓀴us.
13227𓀴
13228𓀴“Of course!” answered the Professor quickly, “you must remain to take
13229𓀴care of your so sweet wife!” Harker was silent for awhile and then said
13230𓀴in a hollow voice:--
13231𓀴
13232𓀴“Let us talk of that part of it in the morning. I want to consult with
13233𓀴Mina.” I thought that now was the time for Van Helsing to warn him not
13234𓀴to disclose our plans to her; but he took no notice. I looked at him
13235𓀴significantly and coughed. For answer he put his finger on his lips and
13236𓀴turned away.
13237𓀴
13238𓀴
13239𓀴_Jonathan Harker’s Journal._
13240𓀴
13241𓀴_5 October, afternoon._--For some time after our meeting this morning I
13242𓀴could not think. The new phases of things leave my mind in a state of
13243𓀴wonder which allows no room for active thought. Mina’s determination not
13244𓀴to take any part in the discussion set me thinking; and as I could not
13245𓀴argue the matter with her, I could only guess. I am as far as ever from
13246𓀴a solution now. The way the others received it, too, puzzled me; the
13247𓀴last time we talked of the subject we agreed that there was to be no
13248𓀴more concealment of anything amongst us. Mina is sleeping now, calmly
13249𓀴and sweetly like a little child. Her lips are curved and her face beams
13250𓀴with happiness. Thank God, there are such moments still for her.
13251𓀴
13252𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
13253𓀴
13254𓀴_Later._--How strange it all is. I sat watching Mina’s happy sleep, and
13255𓀴came as near to being happy myself as I suppose I shall ever be. As the
13256𓀴evening drew on, and the earth took its shadows from the sun sinking
13257𓀴lower, the silence of the room grew more and more solemn to me. All at
13258𓀴once Mina opened her eyes, and looking at me tenderly, said:--
13259𓀴
13260𓀴“Jonathan, I want you to promise me something on your word of honour. A
13261𓀴promise made to me, but made holily in God’s hearing, and not to be
13262𓀴broken though I should go down on my knees and implore you with bitter
13263𓀴tears. Quick, you must make it to me at once.”
13264𓀴
13265𓀴“Mina,” I said, “a promise like that, I cannot make at once. I may have
13266𓀴no right to make it.”
13267𓀴
13268𓀴“But, dear one,” she said, with such spiritual intensity that her eyes
13269𓀴were like pole stars, “it is I who wish it; and it is not for myself.
13270𓀴You can ask Dr. Van Helsing if I am not right; if he disagrees you may
13271𓀴do as you will. Nay, more, if you all agree, later, you are absolved
13272𓀴from the promise.”
13273𓀴
13274𓀴“I promise!” I said, and for a moment she looked supremely happy; though
13275𓀴to me all happiness for her was denied by the red scar on her forehead.
13276𓀴She said:--
13277𓀴
13278𓀴“Promise me that you will not tell me anything of the plans formed for
13279𓀴the campaign against the Count. Not by word, or inference, or
13280𓀴implication; not at any time whilst this remains to me!” and she
13281𓀴solemnly pointed to the scar. I saw that she was in earnest, and said
13282𓀴solemnly:--
13283𓀴
13284𓀴“I promise!” and as I said it I felt that from that instant a door had
13285𓀴been shut between us.
13286𓀴
13287𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
13288𓀴
13289𓀴_Later, midnight._--Mina has been bright and cheerful all the evening.
13290𓀴So much so that all the rest seemed to take courage, as if infected
13291𓀴somewhat with her gaiety; as a result even I myself felt as if the pall
13292𓀴of gloom which weighs us down were somewhat lifted. We all retired
13293𓀴early. Mina is now sleeping like a little child; it is a wonderful thing
13294𓀴that her faculty of sleep remains to her in the midst of her terrible
13295𓀴trouble. Thank God for it, for then at least she can forget her care.
13296𓀴Perhaps her example may affect me as her gaiety did to-night. I shall
13297𓀴try it. Oh! for a dreamless sleep.
13298𓀴
13299𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
13300𓀴
13301𓀴_6 October, morning._--Another surprise. Mina woke me early, about the
13302𓀴same time as yesterday, and asked me to bring Dr. Van Helsing. I thought
13303𓀴that it was another occasion for hypnotism, and without question went
13304𓀴for the Professor. He had evidently expected some such call, for I found
13305𓀴him dressed in his room. His door was ajar, so that he could hear the
13306𓀴opening of the door of our room. He came at once; as he passed into the
13307𓀴room, he asked Mina if the others might come, too.
13308𓀴
13309𓀴“No,” she said quite simply, “it will not be necessary. You can tell
13310𓀴them just as well. I must go with you on your journey.”
13311𓀴
13312𓀴Dr. Van Helsing was as startled as I was. After a moment’s pause he
13313𓀴asked:--
13314𓀴
13315𓀴“But why?”
13316𓀴
13317𓀴“You must take me with you. I am safer with you, and you shall be safer,
13318𓀴too.”
13319𓀴
13320𓀴“But why, dear Madam Mina? You know that your safety is our solemnest
13321𓀴duty. We go into danger, to which you are, or may be, more liable than
13322𓀴any of us from--from circumstances--things that have been.” He paused,
13323𓀴embarrassed.
13324𓀴
13325𓀴As she replied, she raised her finger and pointed to her forehead:--
13326𓀴
13327𓀴“I know. That is why I must go. I can tell you now, whilst the sun is
13328𓀴coming up; I may not be able again. I know that when the Count wills me
13329𓀴I must go. I know that if he tells me to come in secret, I must come by
13330𓀴wile; by any device to hoodwink--even Jonathan.” God saw the look that
13331𓀴she turned on me as she spoke, and if there be indeed a Recording Angel
13332𓀴that look is noted to her everlasting honour. I could only clasp her
13333𓀴hand. I could not speak; my emotion was too great for even the relief of
13334𓀴tears. She went on:--
13335𓀴
13336𓀴“You men are brave and strong. You are strong in your numbers, for you
13337𓀴can defy that which would break down the human endurance of one who had
13338𓀴to guard alone. Besides, I may be of service, since you can hypnotise me
13339𓀴and so learn that which even I myself do not know.” Dr. Van Helsing said
13340𓀴very gravely:--
13341𓀴
13342𓀴“Madam Mina, you are, as always, most wise. You shall with us come; and
13343𓀴together we shall do that which we go forth to achieve.” When he had
13344𓀴spoken, Mina’s long spell of silence made me look at her. She had fallen
13345𓀴back on her pillow asleep; she did not even wake when I had pulled up
13346𓀴the blind and let in the sunlight which flooded the room. Van Helsing
13347𓀴motioned to me to come with him quietly. We went to his room, and within
13348𓀴a minute Lord Godalming, Dr. Seward, and Mr. Morris were with us also.
13349𓀴He told them what Mina had said, and went on:--
13350𓀴
13351𓀴“In the morning we shall leave for Varna. We have now to deal with a
13352𓀴new factor: Madam Mina. Oh, but her soul is true. It is to her an agony
13353𓀴to tell us so much as she has done; but it is most right, and we are
13354𓀴warned in time. There must be no chance lost, and in Varna we must be
13355𓀴ready to act the instant when that ship arrives.”
13356𓀴
13357𓀴“What shall we do exactly?” asked Mr. Morris laconically. The Professor
13358𓀴paused before replying:--
13359𓀴
13360𓀴“We shall at the first board that ship; then, when we have identified
13361𓀴the box, we shall place a branch of the wild rose on it. This we shall
13362𓀴fasten, for when it is there none can emerge; so at least says the
13363𓀴superstition. And to superstition must we trust at the first; it was
13364𓀴man’s faith in the early, and it have its root in faith still. Then,
13365𓀴when we get the opportunity that we seek, when none are near to see, we
13366𓀴shall open the box, and--and all will be well.”
13367𓀴
13368𓀴“I shall not wait for any opportunity,” said Morris. “When I see the box
13369𓀴I shall open it and destroy the monster, though there were a thousand
13370𓀴men looking on, and if I am to be wiped out for it the next moment!” I
13371𓀴grasped his hand instinctively and found it as firm as a piece of steel.
13372𓀴I think he understood my look; I hope he did.
13373𓀴
13374𓀴“Good boy,” said Dr. Van Helsing. “Brave boy. Quincey is all man. God
13375𓀴bless him for it. My child, believe me none of us shall lag behind or
13376𓀴pause from any fear. I do but say what we may do--what we must do. But,
13377𓀴indeed, indeed we cannot say what we shall do. There are so many things
13378𓀴which may happen, and their ways and their ends are so various that
13379𓀴until the moment we may not say. We shall all be armed, in all ways; and
13380𓀴when the time for the end has come, our effort shall not be lack. Now
13381𓀴let us to-day put all our affairs in order. Let all things which touch
13382𓀴on others dear to us, and who on us depend, be complete; for none of us
13383𓀴can tell what, or when, or how, the end may be. As for me, my own
13384𓀴affairs are regulate; and as I have nothing else to do, I shall go make
13385𓀴arrangements for the travel. I shall have all tickets and so forth for
13386𓀴our journey.”
13387𓀴
13388𓀴There was nothing further to be said, and we parted. I shall now settle
13389𓀴up all my affairs of earth, and be ready for whatever may come....
13390𓀴
13391𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
13392𓀴
13393𓀴_Later._--It is all done; my will is made, and all complete. Mina if she
13394𓀴survive is my sole heir. If it should not be so, then the others who
13395𓀴have been so good to us shall have remainder.
13396𓀴
13397𓀴It is now drawing towards the sunset; Mina’s uneasiness calls my
13398𓀴attention to it. I am sure that there is something on her mind which the
13399𓀴time of exact sunset will reveal. These occasions are becoming harrowing
13400𓀴times for us all, for each sunrise and sunset opens up some new
13401𓀴danger--some new pain, which, however, may in God’s will be means to a
13402𓀴good end. I write all these things in the diary since my darling must
13403𓀴not hear them now; but if it may be that she can see them again, they
13404𓀴shall be ready.
13405𓀴
13406𓀴She is calling to me.
13407𓀴
13408𓀴
13409𓀴
13410𓀴
13411𓀴CHAPTER XXV
13412𓀴
13413𓀴DR. SEWARD’S DIARY
13414𓀴
13415𓀴
13416𓀴_11 October, Evening._--Jonathan Harker has asked me to note this, as he
13417𓀴says he is hardly equal to the task, and he wants an exact record kept.
13418𓀴
13419𓀴I think that none of us were surprised when we were asked to see Mrs.
13420𓀴Harker a little before the time of sunset. We have of late come to
13421𓀴understand that sunrise and sunset are to her times of peculiar freedom;
13422𓀴when her old self can be manifest without any controlling force subduing
13423𓀴or restraining her, or inciting her to action. This mood or condition
