13424𓀴begins some half hour or more before actual sunrise or sunset, and lasts
13425𓀴till either the sun is high, or whilst the clouds are still aglow with
13426𓀴the rays streaming above the horizon. At first there is a sort of
13427𓀴negative condition, as if some tie were loosened, and then the absolute
13428𓀴freedom quickly follows; when, however, the freedom ceases the
13429𓀴change-back or relapse comes quickly, preceded only by a spell of
13430𓀴warning silence.
13431𓀴
13432𓀴To-night, when we met, she was somewhat constrained, and bore all the
13433𓀴signs of an internal struggle. I put it down myself to her making a
13434𓀴violent effort at the earliest instant she could do so. A very few
13435𓀴minutes, however, gave her complete control of herself; then, motioning
13436𓀴her husband to sit beside her on the sofa where she was half reclining,
13437𓀴she made the rest of us bring chairs up close. Taking her husband’s hand
13438𓀴in hers began:--
13439𓀴
13440𓀴“We are all here together in freedom, for perhaps the last time! I know,
13441𓀴dear; I know that you will always be with me to the end.” This was to
13442𓀴her husband whose hand had, as we could see, tightened upon hers. “In
13443𓀴the morning we go out upon our task, and God alone knows what may be in
13444𓀴store for any of us. You are going to be so good to me as to take me
13445𓀴with you. I know that all that brave earnest men can do for a poor weak
13446𓀴woman, whose soul perhaps is lost--no, no, not yet, but is at any rate
13447𓀴at stake--you will do. But you must remember that I am not as you are.
13448𓀴There is a poison in my blood, in my soul, which may destroy me; which
13449𓀴must destroy me, unless some relief comes to us. Oh, my friends, you
13450𓀴know as well as I do, that my soul is at stake; and though I know there
13451𓀴is one way out for me, you must not and I must not take it!” She looked
13452𓀴appealingly to us all in turn, beginning and ending with her husband.
13453𓀴
13454𓀴“What is that way?” asked Van Helsing in a hoarse voice. “What is that
13455𓀴way, which we must not--may not--take?”
13456𓀴
13457𓀴“That I may die now, either by my own hand or that of another, before
13458𓀴the greater evil is entirely wrought. I know, and you know, that were I
13459𓀴once dead you could and would set free my immortal spirit, even as you
13460𓀴did my poor Lucy’s. Were death, or the fear of death, the only thing
13461𓀴that stood in the way I would not shrink to die here, now, amidst the
13462𓀴friends who love me. But death is not all. I cannot believe that to die
13463𓀴in such a case, when there is hope before us and a bitter task to be
13464𓀴done, is God’s will. Therefore, I, on my part, give up here the
13465𓀴certainty of eternal rest, and go out into the dark where may be the
13466𓀴blackest things that the world or the nether world holds!” We were all
13467𓀴silent, for we knew instinctively that this was only a prelude. The
13468𓀴faces of the others were set and Harker’s grew ashen grey; perhaps he
13469𓀴guessed better than any of us what was coming. She continued:--
13470𓀴
13471𓀴“This is what I can give into the hotch-pot.” I could not but note the
13472𓀴quaint legal phrase which she used in such a place, and with all
13473𓀴seriousness. “What will each of you give? Your lives I know,” she went
13474𓀴on quickly, “that is easy for brave men. Your lives are God’s, and you
13475𓀴can give them back to Him; but what will you give to me?” She looked
13476𓀴again questioningly, but this time avoided her husband’s face. Quincey
13477𓀴seemed to understand; he nodded, and her face lit up. “Then I shall tell
13478𓀴you plainly what I want, for there must be no doubtful matter in this
13479𓀴connection between us now. You must promise me, one and all--even you,
13480𓀴my beloved husband--that, should the time come, you will kill me.”
13481𓀴
13482𓀴“What is that time?” The voice was Quincey’s, but it was low and
13483𓀴strained.
13484𓀴
13485𓀴“When you shall be convinced that I am so changed that it is better that
13486𓀴I die than I may live. When I am thus dead in the flesh, then you will,
13487𓀴without a moment’s delay, drive a stake through me and cut off my head;
13488𓀴or do whatever else may be wanting to give me rest!”
13489𓀴
13490𓀴Quincey was the first to rise after the pause. He knelt down before her
13491𓀴and taking her hand in his said solemnly:--
13492𓀴
13493𓀴“I’m only a rough fellow, who hasn’t, perhaps, lived as a man should to
13494𓀴win such a distinction, but I swear to you by all that I hold sacred and
13495𓀴dear that, should the time ever come, I shall not flinch from the duty
13496𓀴that you have set us. And I promise you, too, that I shall make all
13497𓀴certain, for if I am only doubtful I shall take it that the time has
13498𓀴come!”
13499𓀴
13500𓀴“My true friend!” was all she could say amid her fast-falling tears, as,
13501𓀴bending over, she kissed his hand.
13502𓀴
13503𓀴“I swear the same, my dear Madam Mina!” said Van Helsing.
13504𓀴
13505𓀴“And I!” said Lord Godalming, each of them in turn kneeling to her to
13506𓀴take the oath. I followed, myself. Then her husband turned to her
13507𓀴wan-eyed and with a greenish pallor which subdued the snowy whiteness of
13508𓀴his hair, and asked:--
13509𓀴
13510𓀴“And must I, too, make such a promise, oh, my wife?”
13511𓀴
13512𓀴“You too, my dearest,” she said, with infinite yearning of pity in her
13513𓀴voice and eyes. “You must not shrink. You are nearest and dearest and
13514𓀴all the world to me; our souls are knit into one, for all life and all
13515𓀴time. Think, dear, that there have been times when brave men have killed
13516𓀴their wives and their womenkind, to keep them from falling into the
13517𓀴hands of the enemy. Their hands did not falter any the more because
13518𓀴those that they loved implored them to slay them. It is men’s duty
13519𓀴towards those whom they love, in such times of sore trial! And oh, my
13520𓀴dear, if it is to be that I must meet death at any hand, let it be at
13521𓀴the hand of him that loves me best. Dr. Van Helsing, I have not
13522𓀴forgotten your mercy in poor Lucy’s case to him who loved”--she stopped
13523𓀴with a flying blush, and changed her phrase--“to him who had best right
13524𓀴to give her peace. If that time shall come again, I look to you to make
13525𓀴it a happy memory of my husband’s life that it was his loving hand which
13526𓀴set me free from the awful thrall upon me.”
13527𓀴
13528𓀴“Again I swear!” came the Professor’s resonant voice. Mrs. Harker
13529𓀴smiled, positively smiled, as with a sigh of relief she leaned back and
13530𓀴said:--
13531𓀴
13532𓀴“And now one word of warning, a warning which you must never forget:
13533𓀴this time, if it ever come, may come quickly and unexpectedly, and in
13534𓀴such case you must lose no time in using your opportunity. At such a
13535𓀴time I myself might be--nay! if the time ever comes, _shall be_--leagued
13536𓀴with your enemy against you.”
13537𓀴
13538𓀴“One more request;” she became very solemn as she said this, “it is not
13539𓀴vital and necessary like the other, but I want you to do one thing for
13540𓀴me, if you will.” We all acquiesced, but no one spoke; there was no need
13541𓀴to speak:--
13542𓀴
13543𓀴“I want you to read the Burial Service.” She was interrupted by a deep
13544𓀴groan from her husband; taking his hand in hers, she held it over her
13545𓀴heart, and continued: “You must read it over me some day. Whatever may
13546𓀴be the issue of all this fearful state of things, it will be a sweet
13547𓀴thought to all or some of us. You, my dearest, will I hope read it, for
13548𓀴then it will be in your voice in my memory for ever--come what may!”
13549𓀴
13550𓀴“But oh, my dear one,” he pleaded, “death is afar off from you.”
13551𓀴
13552𓀴“Nay,” she said, holding up a warning hand. “I am deeper in death at
13553𓀴this moment than if the weight of an earthly grave lay heavy upon me!”
13554𓀴
13555𓀴“Oh, my wife, must I read it?” he said, before he began.
13556𓀴
13557𓀴“It would comfort me, my husband!” was all she said; and he began to
13558𓀴read when she had got the book ready.
13559𓀴
13560𓀴“How can I--how could any one--tell of that strange scene, its
13561𓀴solemnity, its gloom, its sadness, its horror; and, withal, its
13562𓀴sweetness. Even a sceptic, who can see nothing but a travesty of bitter
13563𓀴truth in anything holy or emotional, would have been melted to the heart
13564𓀴had he seen that little group of loving and devoted friends kneeling
13565𓀴round that stricken and sorrowing lady; or heard the tender passion of
13566𓀴her husband’s voice, as in tones so broken with emotion that often he
13567𓀴had to pause, he read the simple and beautiful service from the Burial
13568𓀴of the Dead. I--I cannot go on--words--and--v-voice--f-fail m-me!”
13569𓀴
13570𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
13571𓀴
13572𓀴She was right in her instinct. Strange as it all was, bizarre as it may
13573𓀴hereafter seem even to us who felt its potent influence at the time, it
13574𓀴comforted us much; and the silence, which showed Mrs. Harker’s coming
13575𓀴relapse from her freedom of soul, did not seem so full of despair to any
13576𓀴of us as we had dreaded.
13577𓀴
13578𓀴
13579𓀴_Jonathan Harker’s Journal._
13580𓀴
13581𓀴_15 October, Varna._--We left Charing Cross on the morning of the 12th,
13582𓀴got to Paris the same night, and took the places secured for us in the
13583𓀴Orient Express. We travelled night and day, arriving here at about five
13584𓀴o’clock. Lord Godalming went to the Consulate to see if any telegram had
13585𓀴arrived for him, whilst the rest of us came on to this hotel--“the
13586𓀴Odessus.” The journey may have had incidents; I was, however, too eager
13587𓀴to get on, to care for them. Until the _Czarina Catherine_ comes into
13588𓀴port there will be no interest for me in anything in the wide world.
13589𓀴Thank God! Mina is well, and looks to be getting stronger; her colour is
13590𓀴coming back. She sleeps a great deal; throughout the journey she slept
13591𓀴nearly all the time. Before sunrise and sunset, however, she is very
13592𓀴wakeful and alert; and it has become a habit for Van Helsing to
13593𓀴hypnotise her at such times. At first, some effort was needed, and he
13594𓀴had to make many passes; but now, she seems to yield at once, as if by
13595𓀴habit, and scarcely any action is needed. He seems to have power at
13596𓀴these particular moments to simply will, and her thoughts obey him. He
13597𓀴always asks her what she can see and hear. She answers to the first:--
13598𓀴
13599𓀴“Nothing; all is dark.” And to the second:--
13600𓀴
13601𓀴“I can hear the waves lapping against the ship, and the water rushing
13602𓀴by. Canvas and cordage strain and masts and yards creak. The wind is
13603𓀴high--I can hear it in the shrouds, and the bow throws back the foam.”
13604𓀴It is evident that the _Czarina Catherine_ is still at sea, hastening on
13605𓀴her way to Varna. Lord Godalming has just returned. He had four
13606𓀴telegrams, one each day since we started, and all to the same effect:
13607𓀴that the _Czarina Catherine_ had not been reported to Lloyd’s from
13608𓀴anywhere. He had arranged before leaving London that his agent should
13609𓀴send him every day a telegram saying if the ship had been reported. He
13610𓀴was to have a message even if she were not reported, so that he might be
13611𓀴sure that there was a watch being kept at the other end of the wire.
13612𓀴
13613𓀴We had dinner and went to bed early. To-morrow we are to see the
13614𓀴Vice-Consul, and to arrange, if we can, about getting on board the ship
13615𓀴as soon as she arrives. Van Helsing says that our chance will be to get
13616𓀴on the boat between sunrise and sunset. The Count, even if he takes the
13617𓀴form of a bat, cannot cross the running water of his own volition, and
13618𓀴so cannot leave the ship. As he dare not change to man’s form without
13619𓀴suspicion--which he evidently wishes to avoid--he must remain in the
13620𓀴box. If, then, we can come on board after sunrise, he is at our mercy;
13621𓀴for we can open the box and make sure of him, as we did of poor Lucy,
13622𓀴before he wakes. What mercy he shall get from us will not count for
13623𓀴much. We think that we shall not have much trouble with officials or the
13624𓀴seamen. Thank God! this is the country where bribery can do anything,
13625𓀴and we are well supplied with money. We have only to make sure that the
13626𓀴ship cannot come into port between sunset and sunrise without our being
13627𓀴warned, and we shall be safe. Judge Moneybag will settle this case, I
13628𓀴think!
13629𓀴
13630𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
13631𓀴
13632𓀴_16 October._--Mina’s report still the same: lapping waves and rushing
13633𓀴water, darkness and favouring winds. We are evidently in good time, and
13634𓀴when we hear of the _Czarina Catherine_ we shall be ready. As she must
13635𓀴pass the Dardanelles we are sure to have some report.
13636𓀴
13637𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
13638𓀴
13639𓀴_17 October._--Everything is pretty well fixed now, I think, to welcome
13640𓀴the Count on his return from his tour. Godalming told the shippers that
13641𓀴he fancied that the box sent aboard might contain something stolen from
13642𓀴a friend of his, and got a half consent that he might open it at his own
13643𓀴risk. The owner gave him a paper telling the Captain to give him every
13644𓀴facility in doing whatever he chose on board the ship, and also a
13645𓀴similar authorisation to his agent at Varna. We have seen the agent, who
13646𓀴was much impressed with Godalming’s kindly manner to him, and we are all
13647𓀴satisfied that whatever he can do to aid our wishes will be done. We
13648𓀴have already arranged what to do in case we get the box open. If the
13649𓀴Count is there, Van Helsing and Seward will cut off his head at once and
13650𓀴drive a stake through his heart. Morris and Godalming and I shall
13651𓀴prevent interference, even if we have to use the arms which we shall
13652𓀴have ready. The Professor says that if we can so treat the Count’s body,
13653𓀴it will soon after fall into dust. In such case there would be no
13654𓀴evidence against us, in case any suspicion of murder were aroused. But
13655𓀴even if it were not, we should stand or fall by our act, and perhaps
13656𓀴some day this very script may be evidence to come between some of us and
13657𓀴a rope. For myself, I should take the chance only too thankfully if it
13658𓀴were to come. We mean to leave no stone unturned to carry out our
13659𓀴intent. We have arranged with certain officials that the instant the
13660𓀴_Czarina Catherine_ is seen, we are to be informed by a special
13661𓀴messenger.
13662𓀴
13663𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
13664𓀴
13665𓀴_24 October._--A whole week of waiting. Daily telegrams to Godalming,
13666𓀴but only the same story: “Not yet reported.” Mina’s morning and evening
13667𓀴hypnotic answer is unvaried: lapping waves, rushing water, and creaking
13668𓀴masts.
13669𓀴
13670𓀴_Telegram, October 24th._
13671𓀴
13672𓀴_Rufus Smith, Lloyd’s, London, to Lord Godalming, care of H. B. M.
13673𓀴Vice-Consul, Varna._
13674𓀴
13675𓀴“_Czarina Catherine_ reported this morning from Dardanelles.”
13676𓀴
13677𓀴
13678𓀴_Dr. Seward’s Diary._
13679𓀴
13680𓀴_25 October._--How I miss my phonograph! To write diary with a pen is
13681𓀴irksome to me; but Van Helsing says I must. We were all wild with
13682𓀴excitement yesterday when Godalming got his telegram from Lloyd’s. I
13683𓀴know now what men feel in battle when the call to action is heard. Mrs.
13684𓀴Harker, alone of our party, did not show any signs of emotion. After
13685𓀴all, it is not strange that she did not; for we took special care not to
13686𓀴let her know anything about it, and we all tried not to show any
13687𓀴excitement when we were in her presence. In old days she would, I am
13688𓀴sure, have noticed, no matter how we might have tried to conceal it; but
13689𓀴in this way she is greatly changed during the past three weeks. The
13690𓀴lethargy grows upon her, and though she seems strong and well, and is
13691𓀴getting back some of her colour, Van Helsing and I are not satisfied. We
13692𓀴talk of her often; we have not, however, said a word to the others. It
13693𓀴would break poor Harker’s heart--certainly his nerve--if he knew that we
13694𓀴had even a suspicion on the subject. Van Helsing examines, he tells me,
13695𓀴her teeth very carefully, whilst she is in the hypnotic condition, for
13696𓀴he says that so long as they do not begin to sharpen there is no active
13697𓀴danger of a change in her. If this change should come, it would be
13698𓀴necessary to take steps!... We both know what those steps would have to
13699𓀴be, though we do not mention our thoughts to each other. We should
13700𓀴neither of us shrink from the task--awful though it be to contemplate.
13701𓀴“Euthanasia” is an excellent and a comforting word! I am grateful to
13702𓀴whoever invented it.
13703𓀴
13704𓀴It is only about 24 hours’ sail from the Dardanelles to here, at the
13705𓀴rate the _Czarina Catherine_ has come from London. She should therefore
13706𓀴arrive some time in the morning; but as she cannot possibly get in
13707𓀴before then, we are all about to retire early. We shall get up at one
13708𓀴o’clock, so as to be ready.
13709𓀴
13710𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
13711𓀴
13712𓀴_25 October, Noon_.--No news yet of the ship’s arrival. Mrs. Harker’s
13713𓀴hypnotic report this morning was the same as usual, so it is possible
13714𓀴that we may get news at any moment. We men are all in a fever of
13715𓀴excitement, except Harker, who is calm; his hands are cold as ice, and
13716𓀴an hour ago I found him whetting the edge of the great Ghoorka knife
13717𓀴which he now always carries with him. It will be a bad lookout for the
13718𓀴Count if the edge of that “Kukri” ever touches his throat, driven by
13719𓀴that stern, ice-cold hand!
13720𓀴
13721𓀴Van Helsing and I were a little alarmed about Mrs. Harker to-day. About
13722𓀴noon she got into a sort of lethargy which we did not like; although we
13723𓀴kept silence to the others, we were neither of us happy about it. She
13724𓀴had been restless all the morning, so that we were at first glad to know
13725𓀴that she was sleeping. When, however, her husband mentioned casually
13726𓀴that she was sleeping so soundly that he could not wake her, we went to
13727𓀴her room to see for ourselves. She was breathing naturally and looked so
13728𓀴well and peaceful that we agreed that the sleep was better for her than
13729𓀴anything else. Poor girl, she has so much to forget that it is no wonder
13730𓀴that sleep, if it brings oblivion to her, does her good.
13731𓀴
13732𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
13733𓀴
13734𓀴_Later._--Our opinion was justified, for when after a refreshing sleep
13735𓀴of some hours she woke up, she seemed brighter and better than she had
13736𓀴been for days. At sunset she made the usual hypnotic report. Wherever he
13737𓀴may be in the Black Sea, the Count is hurrying to his destination. To
13738𓀴his doom, I trust!
13739𓀴
13740𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
13741𓀴
13742𓀴_26 October._--Another day and no tidings of the _Czarina Catherine_.
13743𓀴She ought to be here by now. That she is still journeying _somewhere_ is
13744𓀴apparent, for Mrs. Harker’s hypnotic report at sunrise was still the
13745𓀴same. It is possible that the vessel may be lying by, at times, for fog;
13746𓀴some of the steamers which came in last evening reported patches of fog
13747𓀴both to north and south of the port. We must continue our watching, as
13748𓀴the ship may now be signalled any moment.
13749𓀴
13750𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
13751𓀴
13752𓀴_27 October, Noon._--Most strange; no news yet of the ship we wait for.
13753𓀴Mrs. Harker reported last night and this morning as usual: “lapping
13754𓀴waves and rushing water,” though she added that “the waves were very
13755𓀴faint.” The telegrams from London have been the same: “no further
13756𓀴report.” Van Helsing is terribly anxious, and told me just now that he
13757𓀴fears the Count is escaping us. He added significantly:--
13758𓀴
13759𓀴“I did not like that lethargy of Madam Mina’s. Souls and memories can do
13760𓀴strange things during trance.” I was about to ask him more, but Harker
13761𓀴just then came in, and he held up a warning hand. We must try to-night
13762𓀴at sunset to make her speak more fully when in her hypnotic state.
13763𓀴
13764𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
13765𓀴
13766𓀴     _28 October._--Telegram. _Rufus Smith, London, to Lord Godalming,
13767𓀴     care H. B. M. Vice Consul, Varna._
13768𓀴
13769𓀴     “_Czarina Catherine_ reported entering Galatz at one o’clock
13770𓀴     to-day.”
13771𓀴
13772𓀴
13773𓀴_Dr. Seward’s Diary._
13774𓀴
13775𓀴_28 October._--When the telegram came announcing the arrival in Galatz I
13776𓀴do not think it was such a shock to any of us as might have been
13777𓀴expected. True, we did not know whence, or how, or when, the bolt would
13778𓀴come; but I think we all expected that something strange would happen.
13779𓀴The delay of arrival at Varna made us individually satisfied that things
13780𓀴would not be just as we had expected; we only waited to learn where the
13781𓀴change would occur. None the less, however, was it a surprise. I suppose
13782𓀴that nature works on such a hopeful basis that we believe against
13783𓀴ourselves that things will be as they ought to be, not as we should know
13784𓀴that they will be. Transcendentalism is a beacon to the angels, even if
13785𓀴it be a will-o’-the-wisp to man. It was an odd experience and we all
13786𓀴took it differently. Van Helsing raised his hand over his head for a
13787𓀴moment, as though in remonstrance with the Almighty; but he said not a
13788𓀴word, and in a few seconds stood up with his face sternly set. Lord
13789𓀴Godalming grew very pale, and sat breathing heavily. I was myself half
13790𓀴stunned and looked in wonder at one after another. Quincey Morris
13791𓀴tightened his belt with that quick movement which I knew so well; in our
13792𓀴old wandering days it meant “action.” Mrs. Harker grew ghastly white, so
13793𓀴that the scar on her forehead seemed to burn, but she folded her hands
13794𓀴meekly and looked up in prayer. Harker smiled--actually smiled--the
13795𓀴dark, bitter smile of one who is without hope; but at the same time his
13796𓀴action belied his words, for his hands instinctively sought the hilt of
13797𓀴the great Kukri knife and rested there. “When does the next train start
13798𓀴for Galatz?” said Van Helsing to us generally.
13799𓀴
13800𓀴“At 6:30 to-morrow morning!” We all started, for the answer came from
13801𓀴Mrs. Harker.
13802𓀴
13803𓀴“How on earth do you know?” said Art.
13804𓀴
13805𓀴“You forget--or perhaps you do not know, though Jonathan does and so
13806𓀴does Dr. Van Helsing--that I am the train fiend. At home in Exeter I
13807𓀴always used to make up the time-tables, so as to be helpful to my
13808𓀴husband. I found it so useful sometimes, that I always make a study of
13809𓀴the time-tables now. I knew that if anything were to take us to Castle
13810𓀴Dracula we should go by Galatz, or at any rate through Bucharest, so I
13811𓀴learned the times very carefully. Unhappily there are not many to learn,
13812𓀴as the only train to-morrow leaves as I say.”
13813𓀴
13814𓀴“Wonderful woman!” murmured the Professor.
13815𓀴
13816𓀴“Can’t we get a special?” asked Lord Godalming. Van Helsing shook his
13817𓀴head: “I fear not. This land is very different from yours or mine; even
13818𓀴if we did have a special, it would probably not arrive as soon as our
13819𓀴regular train. Moreover, we have something to prepare. We must think.
13820𓀴Now let us organize. You, friend Arthur, go to the train and get the
13821𓀴tickets and arrange that all be ready for us to go in the morning. Do
13822𓀴you, friend Jonathan, go to the agent of the ship and get from him
13823𓀴letters to the agent in Galatz, with authority to make search the ship
13824𓀴just as it was here. Morris Quincey, you see the Vice-Consul, and get
13825𓀴his aid with his fellow in Galatz and all he can do to make our way
13826𓀴smooth, so that no times be lost when over the Danube. John will stay
13827𓀴with Madam Mina and me, and we shall consult. For so if time be long you
13828𓀴may be delayed; and it will not matter when the sun set, since I am here
13829𓀴with Madam to make report.”
13830𓀴
13831𓀴“And I,” said Mrs. Harker brightly, and more like her old self than she
13832𓀴had been for many a long day, “shall try to be of use in all ways, and
13833𓀴shall think and write for you as I used to do. Something is shifting
13834𓀴from me in some strange way, and I feel freer than I have been of late!”
13835𓀴The three younger men looked happier at the moment as they seemed to
13836𓀴realise the significance of her words; but Van Helsing and I, turning to
13837𓀴each other, met each a grave and troubled glance. We said nothing at the
13838𓀴time, however.
13839𓀴
13840𓀴When the three men had gone out to their tasks Van Helsing asked Mrs.
13841𓀴Harker to look up the copy of the diaries and find him the part of
13842𓀴Harker’s journal at the Castle. She went away to get it; when the door
13843𓀴was shut upon her he said to me:--
13844𓀴
13845𓀴“We mean the same! speak out!”
13846𓀴
13847𓀴“There is some change. It is a hope that makes me sick, for it may
13848𓀴deceive us.”
13849𓀴
13850𓀴“Quite so. Do you know why I asked her to get the manuscript?”
13851𓀴
13852𓀴“No!” said I, “unless it was to get an opportunity of seeing me alone.”
13853𓀴
13854𓀴“You are in part right, friend John, but only in part. I want to tell
13855𓀴you something. And oh, my friend, I am taking a great--a terrible--risk;
13856𓀴but I believe it is right. In the moment when Madam Mina said those
13857𓀴words that arrest both our understanding, an inspiration came to me. In
13858𓀴the trance of three days ago the Count sent her his spirit to read her
13859𓀴mind; or more like he took her to see him in his earth-box in the ship
13860𓀴with water rushing, just as it go free at rise and set of sun. He learn
13861𓀴then that we are here; for she have more to tell in her open life with
13862𓀴eyes to see and ears to hear than he, shut, as he is, in his coffin-box.
13863𓀴Now he make his most effort to escape us. At present he want her not.
13864𓀴
13865𓀴“He is sure with his so great knowledge that she will come at his call;
13866𓀴but he cut her off--take her, as he can do, out of his own power, that
13867𓀴so she come not to him. Ah! there I have hope that our man-brains that
13868𓀴have been of man so long and that have not lost the grace of God, will
13869𓀴come higher than his child-brain that lie in his tomb for centuries,
13870𓀴that grow not yet to our stature, and that do only work selfish and
13871𓀴therefore small. Here comes Madam Mina; not a word to her of her trance!
13872𓀴She know it not; and it would overwhelm her and make despair just when
13873𓀴we want all her hope, all her courage; when most we want all her great
13874𓀴brain which is trained like man’s brain, but is of sweet woman and have
13875𓀴a special power which the Count give her, and which he may not take away
13876𓀴altogether--though he think not so. Hush! let me speak, and you shall
13877𓀴learn. Oh, John, my friend, we are in awful straits. I fear, as I never
13878𓀴feared before. We can only trust the good God. Silence! here she comes!”
13879𓀴
13880𓀴I thought that the Professor was going to break down and have hysterics,
13881𓀴just as he had when Lucy died, but with a great effort he controlled
13882𓀴himself and was at perfect nervous poise when Mrs. Harker tripped into
13883𓀴the room, bright and happy-looking and, in the doing of work, seemingly
13884𓀴forgetful of her misery. As she came in, she handed a number of sheets
13885𓀴of typewriting to Van Helsing. He looked over them gravely, his face
13886𓀴brightening up as he read. Then holding the pages between his finger and
13887𓀴thumb he said:--
13888𓀴
13889𓀴“Friend John, to you with so much of experience already--and you, too,
13890𓀴dear Madam Mina, that are young--here is a lesson: do not fear ever to
13891𓀴think. A half-thought has been buzzing often in my brain, but I fear to
13892𓀴let him loose his wings. Here now, with more knowledge, I go back to
13893𓀴where that half-thought come from and I find that he be no half-thought
13894𓀴at all; that be a whole thought, though so young that he is not yet
13895𓀴strong to use his little wings. Nay, like the “Ugly Duck” of my friend
13896𓀴Hans Andersen, he be no duck-thought at all, but a big swan-thought that
13897𓀴sail nobly on big wings, when the time come for him to try them. See I
13898𓀴read here what Jonathan have written:--
13899𓀴
13900𓀴“That other of his race who, in a later age, again and again, brought
13901𓀴his forces over The Great River into Turkey Land; who, when he was
13902𓀴beaten back, came again, and again, and again, though he had to come
13903𓀴alone from the bloody field where his troops were being slaughtered,
13904𓀴since he knew that he alone could ultimately triumph.”
13905𓀴
13906𓀴“What does this tell us? Not much? no! The Count’s child-thought see
13907𓀴nothing; therefore he speak so free. Your man-thought see nothing; my
13908𓀴man-thought see nothing, till just now. No! But there comes another word
13909𓀴from some one who speak without thought because she, too, know not what
13910𓀴it mean--what it _might_ mean. Just as there are elements which rest,
13911𓀴yet when in nature’s course they move on their way and they touch--then
13912𓀴pouf! and there comes a flash of light, heaven wide, that blind and kill
13913𓀴and destroy some; but that show up all earth below for leagues and
13914𓀴leagues. Is it not so? Well, I shall explain. To begin, have you ever
13915𓀴study the philosophy of crime? ‘Yes’ and ‘No.’ You, John, yes; for it is
13916𓀴a study of insanity. You, no, Madam Mina; for crime touch you not--not
13917𓀴but once. Still, your mind works true, and argues not _a particulari ad
13918𓀴universale_. There is this peculiarity in criminals. It is so constant,
13919𓀴in all countries and at all times, that even police, who know not much
13920𓀴from philosophy, come to know it empirically, that _it is_. That is to
13921𓀴be empiric. The criminal always work at one crime--that is the true
13922𓀴criminal who seems predestinate to crime, and who will of none other.
13923𓀴This criminal has not full man-brain. He is clever and cunning and
13924𓀴resourceful; but he be not of man-stature as to brain. He be of
13925𓀴child-brain in much. Now this criminal of ours is predestinate to crime
13926𓀴also; he, too, have child-brain, and it is of the child to do what he
13927𓀴have done. The little bird, the little fish, the little animal learn not
13928𓀴by principle, but empirically; and when he learn to do, then there is to
13929𓀴him the ground to start from to do more. ‘_Dos pou sto_,’ said
13930𓀴Archimedes. ‘Give me a fulcrum, and I shall move the world!’ To do once,
13931𓀴is the fulcrum whereby child-brain become man-brain; and until he have
13932𓀴the purpose to do more, he continue to do the same again every time,
13933𓀴just as he have done before! Oh, my dear, I see that your eyes are
13934𓀴opened, and that to you the lightning flash show all the leagues,” for
13935𓀴Mrs. Harker began to clap her hands and her eyes sparkled. He went on:--
13936𓀴
13937𓀴“Now you shall speak. Tell us two dry men of science what you see with
13938𓀴those so bright eyes.” He took her hand and held it whilst she spoke.
13939𓀴His finger and thumb closed on her pulse, as I thought instinctively and
13940𓀴unconsciously, as she spoke:--
13941𓀴
13942𓀴“The Count is a criminal and of criminal type. Nordau and Lombroso would
13943𓀴so classify him, and _quâ_ criminal he is of imperfectly formed mind.
13944𓀴Thus, in a difficulty he has to seek resource in habit. His past is a
13945𓀴clue, and the one page of it that we know--and that from his own
13946𓀴lips--tells that once before, when in what Mr. Morris would call a
13947𓀴‘tight place,’ he went back to his own country from the land he had
13948𓀴tried to invade, and thence, without losing purpose, prepared himself
13949𓀴for a new effort. He came again better equipped for his work; and won.
13950𓀴So he came to London to invade a new land. He was beaten, and when all
13951𓀴hope of success was lost, and his existence in danger, he fled back over
13952𓀴the sea to his home; just as formerly he had fled back over the Danube
13953𓀴from Turkey Land.”
13954𓀴
13955𓀴“Good, good! oh, you so clever lady!” said Van Helsing,
13956𓀴enthusiastically, as he stooped and kissed her hand. A moment later he
13957𓀴said to me, as calmly as though we had been having a sick-room
13958𓀴consultation:--
13959𓀴
13960𓀴“Seventy-two only; and in all this excitement. I have hope.” Turning to
13961𓀴her again, he said with keen expectation:--
13962𓀴
13963𓀴“But go on. Go on! there is more to tell if you will. Be not afraid;
13964𓀴John and I know. I do in any case, and shall tell you if you are right.
13965𓀴Speak, without fear!”
13966𓀴
13967𓀴“I will try to; but you will forgive me if I seem egotistical.”
13968𓀴
13969𓀴“Nay! fear not, you must be egotist, for it is of you that we think.”
13970𓀴
13971𓀴“Then, as he is criminal he is selfish; and as his intellect is small
13972𓀴and his action is based on selfishness, he confines himself to one
13973𓀴purpose. That purpose is remorseless. As he fled back over the Danube,
13974𓀴leaving his forces to be cut to pieces, so now he is intent on being
13975𓀴safe, careless of all. So his own selfishness frees my soul somewhat
13976𓀴from the terrible power which he acquired over me on that dreadful
13977𓀴night. I felt it! Oh, I felt it! Thank God, for His great mercy! My soul
13978𓀴is freer than it has been since that awful hour; and all that haunts me
13979𓀴is a fear lest in some trance or dream he may have used my knowledge for
13980𓀴his ends.” The Professor stood up:--
13981𓀴
13982𓀴“He has so used your mind; and by it he has left us here in Varna,
13983𓀴whilst the ship that carried him rushed through enveloping fog up to
13984𓀴Galatz, where, doubtless, he had made preparation for escaping from us.
13985𓀴But his child-mind only saw so far; and it may be that, as ever is in
13986𓀴God’s Providence, the very thing that the evil-doer most reckoned on for
13987𓀴his selfish good, turns out to be his chiefest harm. The hunter is taken
13988𓀴in his own snare, as the great Psalmist says. For now that he think he
13989𓀴is free from every trace of us all, and that he has escaped us with so
13990𓀴many hours to him, then his selfish child-brain will whisper him to
13991𓀴sleep. He think, too, that as he cut himself off from knowing your mind,
13992𓀴there can be no knowledge of him to you; there is where he fail! That
13993𓀴terrible baptism of blood which he give you makes you free to go to him
13994𓀴in spirit, as you have as yet done in your times of freedom, when the
13995𓀴sun rise and set. At such times you go by my volition and not by his;
13996𓀴and this power to good of you and others, as you have won from your
13997𓀴suffering at his hands. This is now all the more precious that he know
13998𓀴it not, and to guard himself have even cut himself off from his
13999𓀴knowledge of our where. We, however, are not selfish, and we believe
14000𓀴that God is with us through all this blackness, and these many dark
14001𓀴hours. We shall follow him; and we shall not flinch; even if we peril
14002𓀴ourselves that we become like him. Friend John, this has been a great
14003𓀴hour; and it have done much to advance us on our way. You must be scribe
14004𓀴and write him all down, so that when the others return from their work
14005𓀴you can give it to them; then they shall know as we do.”
14006𓀴
14007𓀴And so I have written it whilst we wait their return, and Mrs. Harker
14008𓀴has written with her typewriter all since she brought the MS. to us.
14009𓀴
14010𓀴
14011𓀴
14012𓀴
14013𓀴CHAPTER XXVI
14014𓀴
14015𓀴DR. SEWARD’S DIARY
14016𓀴
14017𓀴
14018𓀴_29 October._--This is written in the train from Varna to Galatz. Last
14019𓀴night we all assembled a little before the time of sunset. Each of us
14020𓀴had done his work as well as he could; so far as thought, and endeavour,
14021𓀴and opportunity go, we are prepared for the whole of our journey, and
14022𓀴for our work when we get to Galatz. When the usual time came round Mrs.
14023𓀴Harker prepared herself for her hypnotic effort; and after a longer and
14024𓀴more serious effort on the part of Van Helsing than has been usually
14025𓀴necessary, she sank into the trance. Usually she speaks on a hint; but
14026𓀴this time the Professor had to ask her questions, and to ask them pretty
14027𓀴resolutely, before we could learn anything; at last her answer came:--
14028𓀴
14029𓀴“I can see nothing; we are still; there are no waves lapping, but only a
14030𓀴steady swirl of water softly running against the hawser. I can hear
14031𓀴men’s voices calling, near and far, and the roll and creak of oars in
14032𓀴the rowlocks. A gun is fired somewhere; the echo of it seems far away.
14033𓀴There is tramping of feet overhead, and ropes and chains are dragged
14034𓀴along. What is this? There is a gleam of light; I can feel the air
14035𓀴blowing upon me.”
14036𓀴
14037𓀴Here she stopped. She had risen, as if impulsively, from where she lay
14038𓀴on the sofa, and raised both her hands, palms upwards, as if lifting a
14039𓀴weight. Van Helsing and I looked at each other with understanding.
14040𓀴Quincey raised his eyebrows slightly and looked at her intently, whilst
14041𓀴Harker’s hand instinctively closed round the hilt of his Kukri. There
14042𓀴was a long pause. We all knew that the time when she could speak was
14043𓀴passing; but we felt that it was useless to say anything. Suddenly she
14044𓀴sat up, and, as she opened her eyes, said sweetly:--
14045𓀴
14046𓀴“Would none of you like a cup of tea? You must all be so tired!” We
14047𓀴could only make her happy, and so acquiesced. She bustled off to get
14048𓀴tea; when she had gone Van Helsing said:--
14049𓀴
14050𓀴“You see, my friends. _He_ is close to land: he has left his
14051𓀴earth-chest. But he has yet to get on shore. In the night he may lie
14052𓀴hidden somewhere; but if he be not carried on shore, or if the ship do
14053𓀴not touch it, he cannot achieve the land. In such case he can, if it be
14054𓀴in the night, change his form and can jump or fly on shore, as he did
14055𓀴at Whitby. But if the day come before he get on shore, then, unless he
14056𓀴be carried he cannot escape. And if he be carried, then the customs men
14057𓀴may discover what the box contain. Thus, in fine, if he escape not on
14058𓀴shore to-night, or before dawn, there will be the whole day lost to him.
14059𓀴We may then arrive in time; for if he escape not at night we shall come
14060𓀴on him in daytime, boxed up and at our mercy; for he dare not be his
14061𓀴true self, awake and visible, lest he be discovered.”
14062𓀴
14063𓀴There was no more to be said, so we waited in patience until the dawn;
14064𓀴at which time we might learn more from Mrs. Harker.
14065𓀴
14066𓀴Early this morning we listened, with breathless anxiety, for her
14067𓀴response in her trance. The hypnotic stage was even longer in coming
14068𓀴than before; and when it came the time remaining until full sunrise was
14069𓀴so short that we began to despair. Van Helsing seemed to throw his whole
14070𓀴soul into the effort; at last, in obedience to his will she made
14071𓀴reply:--
14072𓀴
14073𓀴“All is dark. I hear lapping water, level with me, and some creaking as
14074𓀴of wood on wood.” She paused, and the red sun shot up. We must wait till
14075𓀴to-night.
14076𓀴
14077𓀴And so it is that we are travelling towards Galatz in an agony of
14078𓀴expectation. We are due to arrive between two and three in the morning;
14079𓀴but already, at Bucharest, we are three hours late, so we cannot
14080𓀴possibly get in till well after sun-up. Thus we shall have two more
14081𓀴hypnotic messages from Mrs. Harker; either or both may possibly throw
14082𓀴more light on what is happening.
14083𓀴
14084𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
14085𓀴
14086𓀴_Later._--Sunset has come and gone. Fortunately it came at a time when
14087𓀴there was no distraction; for had it occurred whilst we were at a
14088𓀴station, we might not have secured the necessary calm and isolation.
14089𓀴Mrs. Harker yielded to the hypnotic influence even less readily than
14090𓀴this morning. I am in fear that her power of reading the Count’s
14091𓀴sensations may die away, just when we want it most. It seems to me that
14092𓀴her imagination is beginning to work. Whilst she has been in the trance
14093𓀴hitherto she has confined herself to the simplest of facts. If this goes
14094𓀴on it may ultimately mislead us. If I thought that the Count’s power
14095𓀴over her would die away equally with her power of knowledge it would be
14096𓀴a happy thought; but I am afraid that it may not be so. When she did
14097𓀴speak, her words were enigmatical:--
14098𓀴
14099𓀴“Something is going out; I can feel it pass me like a cold wind. I can
14100𓀴hear, far off, confused sounds--as of men talking in strange tongues,
14101𓀴fierce-falling water, and the howling of wolves.” She stopped and a
14102𓀴shudder ran through her, increasing in intensity for a few seconds,
14103𓀴till, at the end, she shook as though in a palsy. She said no more, even
14104𓀴in answer to the Professor’s imperative questioning. When she woke from
14105𓀴the trance, she was cold, and exhausted, and languid; but her mind was
14106𓀴all alert. She could not remember anything, but asked what she had said;
14107𓀴when she was told, she pondered over it deeply for a long time and in
14108𓀴silence.
14109𓀴
14110𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
14111𓀴
14112𓀴_30 October, 7 a. m._--We are near Galatz now, and I may not have time
14113𓀴to write later. Sunrise this morning was anxiously looked for by us all.
14114𓀴Knowing of the increasing difficulty of procuring the hypnotic trance,
14115𓀴Van Helsing began his passes earlier than usual. They produced no
14116𓀴effect, however, until the regular time, when she yielded with a still
14117𓀴greater difficulty, only a minute before the sun rose. The Professor
14118𓀴lost no time in his questioning; her answer came with equal quickness:--
14119𓀴
14120𓀴“All is dark. I hear water swirling by, level with my ears, and the
14121𓀴creaking of wood on wood. Cattle low far off. There is another sound, a
14122𓀴queer one like----” She stopped and grew white, and whiter still.
14123𓀴
14124𓀴“Go on; go on! Speak, I command you!” said Van Helsing in an agonised
14125𓀴voice. At the same time there was despair in his eyes, for the risen sun
14126𓀴was reddening even Mrs. Harker’s pale face. She opened her eyes, and we
14127𓀴all started as she said, sweetly and seemingly with the utmost
14128𓀴unconcern:--
14129𓀴
14130𓀴“Oh, Professor, why ask me to do what you know I can’t? I don’t remember
14131𓀴anything.” Then, seeing the look of amazement on our faces, she said,
14132𓀴turning from one to the other with a troubled look:--
14133𓀴
14134𓀴“What have I said? What have I done? I know nothing, only that I was
14135𓀴lying here, half asleep, and heard you say ‘go on! speak, I command you!’
14136𓀴It seemed so funny to hear you order me about, as if I were a bad
14137𓀴child!”
14138𓀴
14139𓀴“Oh, Madam Mina,” he said, sadly, “it is proof, if proof be needed, of
14140𓀴how I love and honour you, when a word for your good, spoken more
14141𓀴earnest than ever, can seem so strange because it is to order her whom I
14142𓀴am proud to obey!”
14143𓀴
14144𓀴The whistles are sounding; we are nearing Galatz. We are on fire with
14145𓀴anxiety and eagerness.
14146𓀴
14147𓀴
14148𓀴_Mina Harker’s Journal._
14149𓀴
14150𓀴_30 October._--Mr. Morris took me to the hotel where our rooms had been
14151𓀴ordered by telegraph, he being the one who could best be spared, since
14152𓀴he does not speak any foreign language. The forces were distributed
14153𓀴much as they had been at Varna, except that Lord Godalming went to the
14154𓀴Vice-Consul, as his rank might serve as an immediate guarantee of some
14155𓀴sort to the official, we being in extreme hurry. Jonathan and the two
14156𓀴doctors went to the shipping agent to learn particulars of the arrival
14157𓀴of the _Czarina Catherine_.
14158𓀴
14159𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
14160𓀴
14161𓀴_Later._--Lord Godalming has returned. The Consul is away, and the
14162𓀴Vice-Consul sick; so the routine work has been attended to by a clerk.
14163𓀴He was very obliging, and offered to do anything in his power.
14164𓀴
14165𓀴
14166𓀴_Jonathan Harker’s Journal._
14167𓀴
14168𓀴_30 October._--At nine o’clock Dr. Van Helsing, Dr. Seward, and I called
14169𓀴on Messrs. Mackenzie & Steinkoff, the agents of the London firm of
14170𓀴Hapgood. They had received a wire from London, in answer to Lord
14171𓀴Godalming’s telegraphed request, asking us to show them any civility in
14172𓀴their power. They were more than kind and courteous, and took us at once
14173𓀴on board the _Czarina Catherine_, which lay at anchor out in the river
14174𓀴harbour. There we saw the Captain, Donelson by name, who told us of his
14175𓀴voyage. He said that in all his life he had never had so favourable a
14176𓀴run.
14177𓀴
14178𓀴“Man!” he said, “but it made us afeard, for we expeckit that we should
14179𓀴have to pay for it wi’ some rare piece o’ ill luck, so as to keep up the
14180𓀴average. It’s no canny to run frae London to the Black Sea wi’ a wind
14181𓀴ahint ye, as though the Deil himself were blawin’ on yer sail for his
14182𓀴ain purpose. An’ a’ the time we could no speer a thing. Gin we were nigh
14183𓀴a ship, or a port, or a headland, a fog fell on us and travelled wi’ us,
14184𓀴till when after it had lifted and we looked out, the deil a thing could
14185𓀴we see. We ran by Gibraltar wi’oot bein’ able to signal; an’ till we
14186𓀴came to the Dardanelles and had to wait to get our permit to pass, we
14187𓀴never were within hail o’ aught. At first I inclined to slack off sail
14188𓀴and beat about till the fog was lifted; but whiles, I thocht that if the
14189𓀴Deil was minded to get us into the Black Sea quick, he was like to do it
14190𓀴whether we would or no. If we had a quick voyage it would be no to our
14191𓀴miscredit wi’ the owners, or no hurt to our traffic; an’ the Old Mon who
14192𓀴had served his ain purpose wad be decently grateful to us for no
14193𓀴hinderin’ him.” This mixture of simplicity and cunning, of superstition
14194𓀴and commercial reasoning, aroused Van Helsing, who said:--
14195𓀴
14196𓀴“Mine friend, that Devil is more clever than he is thought by some; and
14197𓀴he know when he meet his match!” The skipper was not displeased with the
14198𓀴compliment, and went on:--
14199𓀴
14200𓀴“When we got past the Bosphorus the men began to grumble; some o’ them,
14201𓀴the Roumanians, came and asked me to heave overboard a big box which had
14202𓀴been put on board by a queer lookin’ old man just before we had started
14203𓀴frae London. I had seen them speer at the fellow, and put out their twa
14204𓀴fingers when they saw him, to guard against the evil eye. Man! but the
14205𓀴supersteetion of foreigners is pairfectly rideeculous! I sent them aboot
14206𓀴their business pretty quick; but as just after a fog closed in on us I
14207𓀴felt a wee bit as they did anent something, though I wouldn’t say it was
14208𓀴agin the big box. Well, on we went, and as the fog didn’t let up for
14209𓀴five days I joost let the wind carry us; for if the Deil wanted to get
14210𓀴somewheres--well, he would fetch it up a’reet. An’ if he didn’t, well,
14211𓀴we’d keep a sharp lookout anyhow. Sure eneuch, we had a fair way and
14212𓀴deep water all the time; and two days ago, when the mornin’ sun came
14213𓀴through the fog, we found ourselves just in the river opposite Galatz.
14214𓀴The Roumanians were wild, and wanted me right or wrong to take out the
14215𓀴box and fling it in the river. I had to argy wi’ them aboot it wi’ a
14216𓀴handspike; an’ when the last o’ them rose off the deck wi’ his head in
14217𓀴his hand, I had convinced them that, evil eye or no evil eye, the
14218𓀴property and the trust of my owners were better in my hands than in the
14219𓀴river Danube. They had, mind ye, taken the box on the deck ready to
14220𓀴fling in, and as it was marked Galatz _via_ Varna, I thocht I’d let it
14221𓀴lie till we discharged in the port an’ get rid o’t althegither. We
14222𓀴didn’t do much clearin’ that day, an’ had to remain the nicht at anchor;
14223𓀴but in the mornin’, braw an’ airly, an hour before sun-up, a man came
14224𓀴aboard wi’ an order, written to him from England, to receive a box
14225𓀴marked for one Count Dracula. Sure eneuch the matter was one ready to
14226𓀴his hand. He had his papers a’ reet, an’ glad I was to be rid o’ the
14227𓀴dam’ thing, for I was beginnin’ masel’ to feel uneasy at it. If the Deil
14228𓀴did have any luggage aboord the ship, I’m thinkin’ it was nane ither
14229𓀴than that same!”
14230𓀴
14231𓀴“What was the name of the man who took it?” asked Dr. Van Helsing with
14232𓀴restrained eagerness.
14233𓀴
14234𓀴“I’ll be tellin’ ye quick!” he answered, and, stepping down to his
14235𓀴cabin, produced a receipt signed “Immanuel Hildesheim.” Burgen-strasse
14236𓀴16 was the address. We found out that this was all the Captain knew; so
14237𓀴with thanks we came away.
14238𓀴
14239𓀴We found Hildesheim in his office, a Hebrew of rather the Adelphi
14240𓀴Theatre type, with a nose like a sheep, and a fez. His arguments were
14241𓀴pointed with specie--we doing the punctuation--and with a little
14242𓀴bargaining he told us what he knew. This turned out to be simple but
14243𓀴important. He had received a letter from Mr. de Ville of London, telling
14244𓀴him to receive, if possible before sunrise so as to avoid customs, a box
14245𓀴which would arrive at Galatz in the _Czarina Catherine_. This he was to
14246𓀴give in charge to a certain Petrof Skinsky, who dealt with the Slovaks
14247𓀴who traded down the river to the port. He had been paid for his work by
14248𓀴an English bank note, which had been duly cashed for gold at the Danube
14249𓀴International Bank. When Skinsky had come to him, he had taken him to
14250𓀴the ship and handed over the box, so as to save porterage. That was all
14251𓀴he knew.
14252𓀴
14253𓀴We then sought for Skinsky, but were unable to find him. One of his
14254𓀴neighbours, who did not seem to bear him any affection, said that he had
14255𓀴gone away two days before, no one knew whither. This was corroborated by
14256𓀴his landlord, who had received by messenger the key of the house
14257𓀴together with the rent due, in English money. This had been between ten
14258𓀴and eleven o’clock last night. We were at a standstill again.
14259𓀴
14260𓀴Whilst we were talking one came running and breathlessly gasped out that
14261𓀴the body of Skinsky had been found inside the wall of the churchyard of
14262𓀴St. Peter, and that the throat had been torn open as if by some wild
14263𓀴animal. Those we had been speaking with ran off to see the horror, the
14264𓀴women crying out “This is the work of a Slovak!” We hurried away lest we
14265𓀴should have been in some way drawn into the affair, and so detained.
14266𓀴
14267𓀴As we came home we could arrive at no definite conclusion. We were all
14268𓀴convinced that the box was on its way, by water, to somewhere; but where
14269𓀴that might be we would have to discover. With heavy hearts we came home
14270𓀴to the hotel to Mina.
14271𓀴
14272𓀴When we met together, the first thing was to consult as to taking Mina
14273𓀴again into our confidence. Things are getting desperate, and it is at
14274𓀴least a chance, though a hazardous one. As a preliminary step, I was
14275𓀴released from my promise to her.
14276𓀴
14277𓀴
14278𓀴_Mina Harker’s Journal._
14279𓀴
14280𓀴_30 October, evening._--They were so tired and worn out and dispirited
14281𓀴that there was nothing to be done till they had some rest; so I asked
14282𓀴them all to lie down for half an hour whilst I should enter everything
14283𓀴up to the moment. I feel so grateful to the man who invented the
14284𓀴“Traveller’s” typewriter, and to Mr. Morris for getting this one for
14285𓀴me. I should have felt quite astray doing the work if I had to write
14286𓀴with a pen....
14287𓀴
14288𓀴It is all done; poor dear, dear Jonathan, what he must have suffered,
14289𓀴what must he be suffering now. He lies on the sofa hardly seeming to
14290𓀴breathe, and his whole body appears in collapse. His brows are knit; his
14291𓀴face is drawn with pain. Poor fellow, maybe he is thinking, and I can
14292𓀴see his face all wrinkled up with the concentration of his thoughts. Oh!
14293𓀴if I could only help at all.... I shall do what I can.
14294𓀴
14295𓀴I have asked Dr. Van Helsing, and he has got me all the papers that I
14296𓀴have not yet seen.... Whilst they are resting, I shall go over all
14297𓀴carefully, and perhaps I may arrive at some conclusion. I shall try to
14298𓀴follow the Professor’s example, and think without prejudice on the facts
14299𓀴before me....
14300𓀴
14301𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
14302𓀴
14303𓀴I do believe that under God’s providence I have made a discovery. I
14304𓀴shall get the maps and look over them....
14305𓀴
14306𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
14307𓀴
14308𓀴I am more than ever sure that I am right. My new conclusion is ready, so
14309𓀴I shall get our party together and read it. They can judge it; it is
14310𓀴well to be accurate, and every minute is precious.
14311𓀴
14312𓀴
14313𓀴_Mina Harker’s Memorandum._
14314𓀴
14315𓀴(Entered in her Journal.)
14316𓀴
14317𓀴_Ground of inquiry._--Count Dracula’s problem is to get back to his own
14318𓀴place.
14319𓀴
14320𓀴(_a_) He must be _brought back_ by some one. This is evident; for had he
14321𓀴power to move himself as he wished he could go either as man, or wolf,
14322𓀴or bat, or in some other way. He evidently fears discovery or
14323𓀴interference, in the state of helplessness in which he must be--confined
14324𓀴as he is between dawn and sunset in his wooden box.
14325𓀴
14326𓀴(_b_) _How is he to be taken?_--Here a process of exclusions may help
14327𓀴us. By road, by rail, by water?
14328𓀴
14329𓀴1. _By Road._--There are endless difficulties, especially in leaving the
14330𓀴city.
14331𓀴
14332𓀴(_x_) There are people; and people are curious, and investigate. A hint,
14333𓀴a surmise, a doubt as to what might be in the box, would destroy him.
14334𓀴
14335𓀴(_y_) There are, or there may be, customs and octroi officers to pass.
14336𓀴
14337𓀴(_z_) His pursuers might follow. This is his highest fear; and in order
14338𓀴to prevent his being betrayed he has repelled, so far as he can, even
14339𓀴his victim--me!
14340𓀴
14341𓀴2. _By Rail._--There is no one in charge of the box. It would have to
14342𓀴take its chance of being delayed; and delay would be fatal, with enemies
14343𓀴on the track. True, he might escape at night; but what would he be, if
14344𓀴left in a strange place with no refuge that he could fly to? This is not
14345𓀴what he intends; and he does not mean to risk it.
14346𓀴
14347𓀴3. _By Water._--Here is the safest way, in one respect, but with most
14348𓀴danger in another. On the water he is powerless except at night; even
14349𓀴then he can only summon fog and storm and snow and his wolves. But were
14350𓀴he wrecked, the living water would engulf him, helpless; and he would
14351𓀴indeed be lost. He could have the vessel drive to land; but if it were
14352𓀴unfriendly land, wherein he was not free to move, his position would
14353𓀴still be desperate.
14354𓀴
14355𓀴We know from the record that he was on the water; so what we have to do
14356𓀴is to ascertain _what_ water.
14357𓀴
14358𓀴The first thing is to realise exactly what he has done as yet; we may,
14359𓀴then, get a light on what his later task is to be.
14360𓀴
14361𓀴_Firstly._--We must differentiate between what he did in London as part
14362𓀴of his general plan of action, when he was pressed for moments and had
14363𓀴to arrange as best he could.
14364𓀴
14365𓀴_Secondly_ we must see, as well as we can surmise it from the facts we
14366𓀴know of, what he has done here.
14367𓀴
14368𓀴As to the first, he evidently intended to arrive at Galatz, and sent
14369𓀴invoice to Varna to deceive us lest we should ascertain his means of
14370𓀴exit from England; his immediate and sole purpose then was to escape.
14371𓀴The proof of this, is the letter of instructions sent to Immanuel
14372𓀴Hildesheim to clear and take away the box _before sunrise_. There is
14373𓀴also the instruction to Petrof Skinsky. These we must only guess at; but
14374𓀴there must have been some letter or message, since Skinsky came to
14375𓀴Hildesheim.
14376𓀴
14377𓀴That, so far, his plans were successful we know. The _Czarina Catherine_
14378𓀴made a phenomenally quick journey--so much so that Captain Donelson’s
14379𓀴suspicions were aroused; but his superstition united with his canniness
14380𓀴played the Count’s game for him, and he ran with his favouring wind
14381𓀴through fogs and all till he brought up blindfold at Galatz. That the
14382𓀴Count’s arrangements were well made, has been proved. Hildesheim cleared
14383𓀴the box, took it off, and gave it to Skinsky. Skinsky took it--and here
14384𓀴we lose the trail. We only know that the box is somewhere on the water,
14385𓀴moving along. The customs and the octroi, if there be any, have been
14386𓀴avoided.
14387𓀴
14388𓀴Now we come to what the Count must have done after his arrival--_on
14389𓀴land_, at Galatz.
14390𓀴
14391𓀴The box was given to Skinsky before sunrise. At sunrise the Count could
14392𓀴appear in his own form. Here, we ask why Skinsky was chosen at all to
14393𓀴aid in the work? In my husband’s diary, Skinsky is mentioned as dealing
14394𓀴with the Slovaks who trade down the river to the port; and the man’s
14395𓀴remark, that the murder was the work of a Slovak, showed the general
14396𓀴feeling against his class. The Count wanted isolation.
14397𓀴
14398𓀴My surmise is, this: that in London the Count decided to get back to his
14399𓀴castle by water, as the most safe and secret way. He was brought from
14400𓀴the castle by Szgany, and probably they delivered their cargo to Slovaks
14401𓀴who took the boxes to Varna, for there they were shipped for London.
14402𓀴Thus the Count had knowledge of the persons who could arrange this
14403𓀴service. When the box was on land, before sunrise or after sunset, he
14404𓀴came out from his box, met Skinsky and instructed him what to do as to
14405𓀴arranging the carriage of the box up some river. When this was done, and
14406𓀴he knew that all was in train, he blotted out his traces, as he thought,
14407𓀴by murdering his agent.
14408𓀴
14409𓀴I have examined the map and find that the river most suitable for the
14410𓀴Slovaks to have ascended is either the Pruth or the Sereth. I read in
14411𓀴the typescript that in my trance I heard cows low and water swirling
14412𓀴level with my ears and the creaking of wood. The Count in his box, then,
14413𓀴was on a river in an open boat--propelled probably either by oars or
14414𓀴poles, for the banks are near and it is working against stream. There
14415𓀴would be no such sound if floating down stream.
14416𓀴
14417𓀴Of course it may not be either the Sereth or the Pruth, but we may
14418𓀴possibly investigate further. Now of these two, the Pruth is the more
14419𓀴easily navigated, but the Sereth is, at Fundu, joined by the Bistritza
14420𓀴which runs up round the Borgo Pass. The loop it makes is manifestly as
14421𓀴close to Dracula’s castle as can be got by water.
14422𓀴
14423𓀴
14424𓀴_Mina Harker’s Journal--continued._
14425𓀴
14426𓀴When I had done reading, Jonathan took me in his arms and kissed me. The
14427𓀴others kept shaking me by both hands, and Dr. Van Helsing said:--
14428𓀴
14429𓀴“Our dear Madam Mina is once more our teacher. Her eyes have been where
14430𓀴we were blinded. Now we are on the track once again, and this time we
14431𓀴may succeed. Our enemy is at his most helpless; and if we can come on
14432𓀴him by day, on the water, our task will be over. He has a start, but he
14433𓀴is powerless to hasten, as he may not leave his box lest those who carry
14434𓀴him may suspect; for them to suspect would be to prompt them to throw
14435𓀴him in the stream where he perish. This he knows, and will not. Now men,
14436𓀴to our Council of War; for, here and now, we must plan what each and all
14437𓀴shall do.”
14438𓀴
14439𓀴“I shall get a steam launch and follow him,” said Lord Godalming.
14440𓀴
14441𓀴“And I, horses to follow on the bank lest by chance he land,” said Mr.
14442𓀴Morris.
14443𓀴
14444𓀴“Good!” said the Professor, “both good. But neither must go alone. There
14445𓀴must be force to overcome force if need be; the Slovak is strong and
14446𓀴rough, and he carries rude arms.” All the men smiled, for amongst them
14447𓀴they carried a small arsenal. Said Mr. Morris:--
14448𓀴
14449𓀴“I have brought some Winchesters; they are pretty handy in a crowd, and
14450𓀴there may be wolves. The Count, if you remember, took some other
14451𓀴precautions; he made some requisitions on others that Mrs. Harker could
14452𓀴not quite hear or understand. We must be ready at all points.” Dr.
14453𓀴Seward said:--
14454𓀴
14455𓀴“I think I had better go with Quincey. We have been accustomed to hunt
14456𓀴together, and we two, well armed, will be a match for whatever may come
14457𓀴along. You must not be alone, Art. It may be necessary to fight the
14458𓀴Slovaks, and a chance thrust--for I don’t suppose these fellows carry
14459𓀴guns--would undo all our plans. There must be no chances, this time; we
14460𓀴shall not rest until the Count’s head and body have been separated, and
14461𓀴we are sure that he cannot re-incarnate.” He looked at Jonathan as he
14462𓀴spoke, and Jonathan looked at me. I could see that the poor dear was
14463𓀴torn about in his mind. Of course he wanted to be with me; but then the
14464𓀴boat service would, most likely, be the one which would destroy the ...
14465𓀴the ... the ... Vampire. (Why did I hesitate to write the word?) He was
14466𓀴silent awhile, and during his silence Dr. Van Helsing spoke:--
14467𓀴
14468𓀴“Friend Jonathan, this is to you for twice reasons. First, because you
14469𓀴are young and brave and can fight, and all energies may be needed at the
14470𓀴last; and again that it is your right to destroy him--that--which has
14471𓀴wrought such woe to you and yours. Be not afraid for Madam Mina; she
14472𓀴will be my care, if I may. I am old. My legs are not so quick to run as
14473𓀴once; and I am not used to ride so long or to pursue as need be, or to
14474𓀴fight with lethal weapons. But I can be of other service; I can fight in
14475𓀴other way. And I can die, if need be, as well as younger men. Now let
14476𓀴me say that what I would is this: while you, my Lord Godalming and
14477𓀴friend Jonathan go in your so swift little steamboat up the river, and
14478𓀴whilst John and Quincey guard the bank where perchance he might be
14479𓀴landed, I will take Madam Mina right into the heart of the enemy’s
14480𓀴country. Whilst the old fox is tied in his box, floating on the running
14481𓀴stream whence he cannot escape to land--where he dares not raise the lid
14482𓀴of his coffin-box lest his Slovak carriers should in fear leave him to
14483𓀴perish--we shall go in the track where Jonathan went,--from Bistritz
14484𓀴over the Borgo, and find our way to the Castle of Dracula. Here, Madam
14485𓀴Mina’s hypnotic power will surely help, and we shall find our way--all
14486𓀴dark and unknown otherwise--after the first sunrise when we are near
14487𓀴that fateful place. There is much to be done, and other places to be
14488𓀴made sanctify, so that that nest of vipers be obliterated.” Here
14489𓀴Jonathan interrupted him hotly:--
14490𓀴
14491𓀴“Do you mean to say, Professor Van Helsing, that you would bring Mina,
14492𓀴in her sad case and tainted as she is with that devil’s illness, right
14493𓀴into the jaws of his death-trap? Not for the world! Not for Heaven or
14494𓀴Hell!” He became almost speechless for a minute, and then went on:--
14495𓀴
14496𓀴“Do you know what the place is? Have you seen that awful den of hellish
14497𓀴infamy--with the very moonlight alive with grisly shapes, and every
14498𓀴speck of dust that whirls in the wind a devouring monster in embryo?
14499𓀴Have you felt the Vampire’s lips upon your throat?” Here he turned to
14500𓀴me, and as his eyes lit on my forehead he threw up his arms with a cry:
14501𓀴“Oh, my God, what have we done to have this terror upon us!” and he sank
14502𓀴down on the sofa in a collapse of misery. The Professor’s voice, as he
14503𓀴spoke in clear, sweet tones, which seemed to vibrate in the air, calmed
14504𓀴us all:--
14505𓀴
14506𓀴“Oh, my friend, it is because I would save Madam Mina from that awful
14507𓀴place that I would go. God forbid that I should take her into that
14508𓀴place. There is work--wild work--to be done there, that her eyes may not
14509𓀴see. We men here, all save Jonathan, have seen with their own eyes what
14510𓀴is to be done before that place can be purify. Remember that we are in
14511𓀴terrible straits. If the Count escape us this time--and he is strong and
14512𓀴subtle and cunning--he may choose to sleep him for a century, and then
14513𓀴in time our dear one”--he took my hand--“would come to him to keep him
14514𓀴company, and would be as those others that you, Jonathan, saw. You have
14515𓀴told us of their gloating lips; you heard their ribald laugh as they
14516𓀴clutched the moving bag that the Count threw to them. You shudder; and
14517𓀴well may it be. Forgive me that I make you so much pain, but it is
14518𓀴necessary. My friend, is it not a dire need for the which I am giving,
14519𓀴possibly my life? If it were that any one went into that place to stay,
14520𓀴it is I who would have to go to keep them company.”
14521𓀴
14522𓀴“Do as you will,” said Jonathan, with a sob that shook him all over, “we
14523𓀴are in the hands of God!”
14524𓀴
14525𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
14526𓀴
14527𓀴_Later._--Oh, it did me good to see the way that these brave men worked.
14528𓀴How can women help loving men when they are so earnest, and so true, and
14529𓀴so brave! And, too, it made me think of the wonderful power of money!
14530𓀴What can it not do when it is properly applied; and what might it do
14531𓀴when basely used. I felt so thankful that Lord Godalming is rich, and
14532𓀴that both he and Mr. Morris, who also has plenty of money, are willing
14533𓀴to spend it so freely. For if they did not, our little expedition could
14534𓀴not start, either so promptly or so well equipped, as it will within
14535𓀴another hour. It is not three hours since it was arranged what part each
14536𓀴of us was to do; and now Lord Godalming and Jonathan have a lovely steam
14537𓀴launch, with steam up ready to start at a moment’s notice. Dr. Seward
14538𓀴and Mr. Morris have half a dozen good horses, well appointed. We have
14539𓀴all the maps and appliances of various kinds that can be had. Professor
14540𓀴Van Helsing and I are to leave by the 11:40 train to-night for Veresti,
14541𓀴where we are to get a carriage to drive to the Borgo Pass. We are
14542𓀴bringing a good deal of ready money, as we are to buy a carriage and
14543𓀴horses. We shall drive ourselves, for we have no one whom we can trust
14544𓀴in the matter. The Professor knows something of a great many languages,
14545𓀴so we shall get on all right. We have all got arms, even for me a
14546𓀴large-bore revolver; Jonathan would not be happy unless I was armed like
14547𓀴the rest. Alas! I cannot carry one arm that the rest do; the scar on my
14548𓀴forehead forbids that. Dear Dr. Van Helsing comforts me by telling me
14549𓀴that I am fully armed as there may be wolves; the weather is getting
14550𓀴colder every hour, and there are snow-flurries which come and go as
14551𓀴warnings.
14552𓀴
14553𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
14554𓀴
14555𓀴_Later._--It took all my courage to say good-bye to my darling. We may
14556𓀴never meet again. Courage, Mina! the Professor is looking at you keenly;
14557𓀴his look is a warning. There must be no tears now--unless it may be that
14558𓀴God will let them fall in gladness.
14559𓀴
14560𓀴
14561𓀴_Jonathan Harker’s Journal._
14562𓀴
14563𓀴_October 30. Night._--I am writing this in the light from the furnace
14564𓀴door of the steam launch: Lord Godalming is firing up. He is an
14565𓀴experienced hand at the work, as he has had for years a launch of his
14566𓀴own on the Thames, and another on the Norfolk Broads. Regarding our
14567𓀴plans, we finally decided that Mina’s guess was correct, and that if any
14568𓀴waterway was chosen for the Count’s escape back to his Castle, the
14569𓀴Sereth and then the Bistritza at its junction, would be the one. We took
14570𓀴it, that somewhere about the 47th degree, north latitude, would be the
14571𓀴place chosen for the crossing the country between the river and the
14572𓀴Carpathians. We have no fear in running at good speed up the river at
14573𓀴night; there is plenty of water, and the banks are wide enough apart to
14574𓀴make steaming, even in the dark, easy enough. Lord Godalming tells me to
14575𓀴sleep for a while, as it is enough for the present for one to be on
14576𓀴watch. But I cannot sleep--how can I with the terrible danger hanging
14577𓀴over my darling, and her going out into that awful place.... My only
14578𓀴comfort is that we are in the hands of God. Only for that faith it would
14579𓀴be easier to die than to live, and so be quit of all the trouble. Mr.
14580𓀴Morris and Dr. Seward were off on their long ride before we started;
14581𓀴they are to keep up the right bank, far enough off to get on higher
14582𓀴lands where they can see a good stretch of river and avoid the following
14583𓀴of its curves. They have, for the first stages, two men to ride and lead
14584𓀴their spare horses--four in all, so as not to excite curiosity. When
14585𓀴they dismiss the men, which shall be shortly, they shall themselves look
14586𓀴after the horses. It may be necessary for us to join forces; if so they
14587𓀴can mount our whole party. One of the saddles has a movable horn, and
14588𓀴can be easily adapted for Mina, if required.
14589𓀴
14590𓀴It is a wild adventure we are on. Here, as we are rushing along through
14591𓀴the darkness, with the cold from the river seeming to rise up and strike
14592𓀴us; with all the mysterious voices of the night around us, it all comes
14593𓀴home. We seem to be drifting into unknown places and unknown ways; into
14594𓀴a whole world of dark and dreadful things. Godalming is shutting the
14595𓀴furnace door....
14596𓀴
14597𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
14598𓀴
14599𓀴_31 October._--Still hurrying along. The day has come, and Godalming is
14600𓀴sleeping. I am on watch. The morning is bitterly cold; the furnace heat
14601𓀴is grateful, though we have heavy fur coats. As yet we have passed only
14602𓀴a few open boats, but none of them had on board any box or package of
14603𓀴anything like the size of the one we seek. The men were scared every
14604𓀴time we turned our electric lamp on them, and fell on their knees and
14605𓀴prayed.
14606𓀴
14607𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
14608𓀴
14609𓀴_1 November, evening._--No news all day; we have found nothing of the
14610𓀴kind we seek. We have now passed into the Bistritza; and if we are wrong
14611𓀴in our surmise our chance is gone. We have over-hauled every boat, big
14612𓀴and little. Early this morning, one crew took us for a Government boat,
14613𓀴and treated us accordingly. We saw in this a way of smoothing matters,
14614𓀴so at Fundu, where the Bistritza runs into the Sereth, we got a
14615𓀴Roumanian flag which we now fly conspicuously. With every boat which we
14616𓀴have over-hauled since then this trick has succeeded; we have had every
14617𓀴deference shown to us, and not once any objection to whatever we chose
14618𓀴to ask or do. Some of the Slovaks tell us that a big boat passed them,
14619𓀴going at more than usual speed as she had a double crew on board. This
14620𓀴was before they came to Fundu, so they could not tell us whether the
14621𓀴boat turned into the Bistritza or continued on up the Sereth. At Fundu
14622𓀴we could not hear of any such boat, so she must have passed there in the
14623𓀴night. I am feeling very sleepy; the cold is perhaps beginning to tell
14624𓀴upon me, and nature must have rest some time. Godalming insists that he
14625𓀴shall keep the first watch. God bless him for all his goodness to poor
14626𓀴dear Mina and me.
14627𓀴
14628𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
14629𓀴
14630𓀴_2 November, morning._--It is broad daylight. That good fellow would not
14631𓀴wake me. He says it would have been a sin to, for I slept peacefully and
14632𓀴was forgetting my trouble. It seems brutally selfish to me to have slept
14633𓀴so long, and let him watch all night; but he was quite right. I am a new
14634𓀴man this morning; and, as I sit here and watch him sleeping, I can do
14635𓀴all that is necessary both as to minding the engine, steering, and
14636𓀴keeping watch. I can feel that my strength and energy are coming back to
14637𓀴me. I wonder where Mina is now, and Van Helsing. They should have got to
14638𓀴Veresti about noon on Wednesday. It would take them some time to get the
14639𓀴carriage and horses; so if they had started and travelled hard, they
14640𓀴would be about now at the Borgo Pass. God guide and help them! I am
14641𓀴afraid to think what may happen. If we could only go faster! but we
14642𓀴cannot; the engines are throbbing and doing their utmost. I wonder how
14643𓀴Dr. Seward and Mr. Morris are getting on. There seem to be endless
14644𓀴streams running down the mountains into this river, but as none of them
14645𓀴are very large--at present, at all events, though they are terrible
14646𓀴doubtless in winter and when the snow melts--the horsemen may not have
14647𓀴met much obstruction. I hope that before we get to Strasba we may see
14648𓀴them; for if by that time we have not overtaken the Count, it may be
14649𓀴necessary to take counsel together what to do next.
14650𓀴
14651𓀴
14652𓀴_Dr. Seward’s Diary._
14653𓀴
14654𓀴_2 November._--Three days on the road. No news, and no time to write it
14655𓀴if there had been, for every moment is precious. We have had only the
14656𓀴rest needful for the horses; but we are both bearing it wonderfully.
14657𓀴Those adventurous days of ours are turning up useful. We must push on;
14658𓀴we shall never feel happy till we get the launch in sight again.
14659𓀴
14660𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
14661𓀴
14662𓀴_3 November._--We heard at Fundu that the launch had gone up the
14663𓀴Bistritza. I wish it wasn’t so cold. There are signs of snow coming; and
14664𓀴if it falls heavy it will stop us. In such case we must get a sledge and
14665𓀴go on, Russian fashion.
14666𓀴
14667𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
14668𓀴
14669𓀴_4 November._--To-day we heard of the launch having been detained by an
14670𓀴accident when trying to force a way up the rapids. The Slovak boats get
14671𓀴up all right, by aid of a rope and steering with knowledge. Some went up
14672𓀴only a few hours before. Godalming is an amateur fitter himself, and
14673𓀴evidently it was he who put the launch in trim again. Finally, they got
14674𓀴up the rapids all right, with local help, and are off on the chase
14675𓀴afresh. I fear that the boat is not any better for the accident; the
14676𓀴peasantry tell us that after she got upon smooth water again, she kept
14677𓀴stopping every now and again so long as she was in sight. We must push
14678𓀴on harder than ever; our help may be wanted soon.
14679𓀴
14680𓀴
14681𓀴_Mina Harker’s Journal._
14682𓀴
14683𓀴_31 October._--Arrived at Veresti at noon. The Professor tells me that
14684𓀴this morning at dawn he could hardly hypnotise me at all, and that all I
14685𓀴could say was: “dark and quiet.” He is off now buying a carriage and
14686𓀴horses. He says that he will later on try to buy additional horses, so
14687𓀴that we may be able to change them on the way. We have something more
14688𓀴than 70 miles before us. The country is lovely, and most interesting; if
14689𓀴only we were under different conditions, how delightful it would be to
14690𓀴see it all. If Jonathan and I were driving through it alone what a
14691𓀴pleasure it would be. To stop and see people, and learn something of
14692𓀴their life, and to fill our minds and memories with all the colour and
14693𓀴picturesqueness of the whole wild, beautiful country and the quaint
14694𓀴people! But, alas!--
14695𓀴
14696𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
14697𓀴
14698𓀴_Later._--Dr. Van Helsing has returned. He has got the carriage and
14699𓀴horses; we are to have some dinner, and to start in an hour. The
14700𓀴landlady is putting us up a huge basket of provisions; it seems enough
14701𓀴for a company of soldiers. The Professor encourages her, and whispers to
14702𓀴me that it may be a week before we can get any good food again. He has
14703𓀴been shopping too, and has sent home such a wonderful lot of fur coats
14704𓀴and wraps, and all sorts of warm things. There will not be any chance of
14705𓀴our being cold.
14706𓀴
14707𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
14708𓀴
14709𓀴We shall soon be off. I am afraid to think what may happen to us. We are
14710𓀴truly in the hands of God. He alone knows what may be, and I pray Him,
14711𓀴with all the strength of my sad and humble soul, that He will watch over
14712𓀴my beloved husband; that whatever may happen, Jonathan may know that I
14713𓀴loved him and honoured him more than I can say, and that my latest and
14714𓀴truest thought will be always for him.
14715𓀴
14716𓀴
14717𓀴
14718𓀴
14719𓀴CHAPTER XXVII
14720𓀴
14721𓀴MINA HARKER’S JOURNAL
14722𓀴
14723𓀴
14724𓀴_1 November._--All day long we have travelled, and at a good speed. The
14725𓀴horses seem to know that they are being kindly treated, for they go
14726𓀴willingly their full stage at best speed. We have now had so many
14727𓀴changes and find the same thing so constantly that we are encouraged to
14728𓀴think that the journey will be an easy one. Dr. Van Helsing is laconic;
14729𓀴he tells the farmers that he is hurrying to Bistritz, and pays them well
14730𓀴to make the exchange of horses. We get hot soup, or coffee, or tea; and
14731𓀴off we go. It is a lovely country; full of beauties of all imaginable
14732𓀴kinds, and the people are brave, and strong, and simple, and seem full
14733𓀴of nice qualities. They are _very, very_ superstitious. In the first
14734𓀴house where we stopped, when the woman who served us saw the scar on my
14735𓀴forehead, she crossed herself and put out two fingers towards me, to
14736𓀴keep off the evil eye. I believe they went to the trouble of putting an
14737𓀴extra amount of garlic into our food; and I can’t abide garlic. Ever
14738𓀴since then I have taken care not to take off my hat or veil, and so have
14739𓀴escaped their suspicions. We are travelling fast, and as we have no
14740𓀴driver with us to carry tales, we go ahead of scandal; but I daresay
14741𓀴that fear of the evil eye will follow hard behind us all the way. The
14742𓀴Professor seems tireless; all day he would not take any rest, though he
14743𓀴made me sleep for a long spell. At sunset time he hypnotised me, and he
14744𓀴says that I answered as usual “darkness, lapping water and creaking
14745𓀴wood”; so our enemy is still on the river. I am afraid to think of
14746𓀴Jonathan, but somehow I have now no fear for him, or for myself. I write
14747𓀴this whilst we wait in a farmhouse for the horses to be got ready. Dr.
