14748𓀴Van Helsing is sleeping. Poor dear, he looks very tired and old and
14749𓀴grey, but his mouth is set as firmly as a conqueror’s; even in his sleep
14750𓀴he is instinct with resolution. When we have well started I must make
14751𓀴him rest whilst I drive. I shall tell him that we have days before us,
14752𓀴and we must not break down when most of all his strength will be
14753𓀴needed.... All is ready; we are off shortly.
14754𓀴
14755𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
14756𓀴
14757𓀴_2 November, morning._--I was successful, and we took turns driving all
14758𓀴night; now the day is on us, bright though cold. There is a strange
14759𓀴heaviness in the air--I say heaviness for want of a better word; I mean
14760𓀴that it oppresses us both. It is very cold, and only our warm furs keep
14761𓀴us comfortable. At dawn Van Helsing hypnotised me; he says I answered
14762𓀴“darkness, creaking wood and roaring water,” so the river is changing as
14763𓀴they ascend. I do hope that my darling will not run any chance of
14764𓀴danger--more than need be; but we are in God’s hands.
14765𓀴
14766𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
14767𓀴
14768𓀴_2 November, night._--All day long driving. The country gets wilder as
14769𓀴we go, and the great spurs of the Carpathians, which at Veresti seemed
14770𓀴so far from us and so low on the horizon, now seem to gather round us
14771𓀴and tower in front. We both seem in good spirits; I think we make an
14772𓀴effort each to cheer the other; in the doing so we cheer ourselves. Dr.
14773𓀴Van Helsing says that by morning we shall reach the Borgo Pass. The
14774𓀴houses are very few here now, and the Professor says that the last horse
14775𓀴we got will have to go on with us, as we may not be able to change. He
14776𓀴got two in addition to the two we changed, so that now we have a rude
14777𓀴four-in-hand. The dear horses are patient and good, and they give us no
14778𓀴trouble. We are not worried with other travellers, and so even I can
14779𓀴drive. We shall get to the Pass in daylight; we do not want to arrive
14780𓀴before. So we take it easy, and have each a long rest in turn. Oh, what
14781𓀴will to-morrow bring to us? We go to seek the place where my poor
14782𓀴darling suffered so much. God grant that we may be guided aright, and
14783𓀴that He will deign to watch over my husband and those dear to us both,
14784𓀴and who are in such deadly peril. As for me, I am not worthy in His
14785𓀴sight. Alas! I am unclean to His eyes, and shall be until He may deign
14786𓀴to let me stand forth in His sight as one of those who have not incurred
14787𓀴His wrath.
14788𓀴
14789𓀴
14790𓀴_Memorandum by Abraham Van Helsing._
14791𓀴
14792𓀴_4 November._--This to my old and true friend John Seward, M.D., of
14793𓀴Purfleet, London, in case I may not see him. It may explain. It is
14794𓀴morning, and I write by a fire which all the night I have kept
14795𓀴alive--Madam Mina aiding me. It is cold, cold; so cold that the grey
14796𓀴heavy sky is full of snow, which when it falls will settle for all
14797𓀴winter as the ground is hardening to receive it. It seems to have
14798𓀴affected Madam Mina; she has been so heavy of head all day that she was
14799𓀴not like herself. She sleeps, and sleeps, and sleeps! She who is usual
14800𓀴so alert, have done literally nothing all the day; she even have lost
14801𓀴her appetite. She make no entry into her little diary, she who write so
14802𓀴faithful at every pause. Something whisper to me that all is not well.
14803𓀴However, to-night she is more _vif_. Her long sleep all day have refresh
14804𓀴and restore her, for now she is all sweet and bright as ever. At sunset
14805𓀴I try to hypnotise her, but alas! with no effect; the power has grown
14806𓀴less and less with each day, and to-night it fail me altogether. Well,
14807𓀴God’s will be done--whatever it may be, and whithersoever it may lead!
14808𓀴
14809𓀴Now to the historical, for as Madam Mina write not in her stenography, I
14810𓀴must, in my cumbrous old fashion, that so each day of us may not go
14811𓀴unrecorded.
14812𓀴
14813𓀴We got to the Borgo Pass just after sunrise yesterday morning. When I
14814𓀴saw the signs of the dawn I got ready for the hypnotism. We stopped our
14815𓀴carriage, and got down so that there might be no disturbance. I made a
14816𓀴couch with furs, and Madam Mina, lying down, yield herself as usual, but
14817𓀴more slow and more short time than ever, to the hypnotic sleep. As
14818𓀴before, came the answer: “darkness and the swirling of water.” Then she
14819𓀴woke, bright and radiant and we go on our way and soon reach the Pass.
14820𓀴At this time and place, she become all on fire with zeal; some new
14821𓀴guiding power be in her manifested, for she point to a road and say:--
14822𓀴
14823𓀴“This is the way.”
14824𓀴
14825𓀴“How know you it?” I ask.
14826𓀴
14827𓀴“Of course I know it,” she answer, and with a pause, add: “Have not my
14828𓀴Jonathan travelled it and wrote of his travel?”
14829𓀴
14830𓀴At first I think somewhat strange, but soon I see that there be only one
14831𓀴such by-road. It is used but little, and very different from the coach
14832𓀴road from the Bukovina to Bistritz, which is more wide and hard, and
14833𓀴more of use.
14834𓀴
14835𓀴So we came down this road; when we meet other ways--not always were we
14836𓀴sure that they were roads at all, for they be neglect and light snow
14837𓀴have fallen--the horses know and they only. I give rein to them, and
14838𓀴they go on so patient. By-and-by we find all the things which Jonathan
14839𓀴have note in that wonderful diary of him. Then we go on for long, long
14840𓀴hours and hours. At the first, I tell Madam Mina to sleep; she try, and
14841𓀴she succeed. She sleep all the time; till at the last, I feel myself to
14842𓀴suspicious grow, and attempt to wake her. But she sleep on, and I may
14843𓀴not wake her though I try. I do not wish to try too hard lest I harm
14844𓀴her; for I know that she have suffer much, and sleep at times be
14845𓀴all-in-all to her. I think I drowse myself, for all of sudden I feel
14846𓀴guilt, as though I have done something; I find myself bolt up, with the
14847𓀴reins in my hand, and the good horses go along jog, jog, just as ever. I
14848𓀴look down and find Madam Mina still sleep. It is now not far off sunset
14849𓀴time, and over the snow the light of the sun flow in big yellow flood,
14850𓀴so that we throw great long shadow on where the mountain rise so steep.
14851𓀴For we are going up, and up; and all is oh! so wild and rocky, as though
14852𓀴it were the end of the world.
14853𓀴
14854𓀴Then I arouse Madam Mina. This time she wake with not much trouble, and
14855𓀴then I try to put her to hypnotic sleep. But she sleep not, being as
14856𓀴though I were not. Still I try and try, till all at once I find her and
14857𓀴myself in dark; so I look round, and find that the sun have gone down.
14858𓀴Madam Mina laugh, and I turn and look at her. She is now quite awake,
14859𓀴and look so well as I never saw her since that night at Carfax when we
14860𓀴first enter the Count’s house. I am amaze, and not at ease then; but she
14861𓀴is so bright and tender and thoughtful for me that I forget all fear. I
14862𓀴light a fire, for we have brought supply of wood with us, and she
14863𓀴prepare food while I undo the horses and set them, tethered in shelter,
14864𓀴to feed. Then when I return to the fire she have my supper ready. I go
14865𓀴to help her; but she smile, and tell me that she have eat already--that
14866𓀴she was so hungry that she would not wait. I like it not, and I have
14867𓀴grave doubts; but I fear to affright her, and so I am silent of it. She
14868𓀴help me and I eat alone; and then we wrap in fur and lie beside the
14869𓀴fire, and I tell her to sleep while I watch. But presently I forget all
14870𓀴of watching; and when I sudden remember that I watch, I find her lying
14871𓀴quiet, but awake, and looking at me with so bright eyes. Once, twice
14872𓀴more the same occur, and I get much sleep till before morning. When I
14873𓀴wake I try to hypnotise her; but alas! though she shut her eyes
14874𓀴obedient, she may not sleep. The sun rise up, and up, and up; and then
14875𓀴sleep come to her too late, but so heavy that she will not wake. I have
14876𓀴to lift her up, and place her sleeping in the carriage when I have
14877𓀴harnessed the horses and made all ready. Madam still sleep, and she look
14878𓀴in her sleep more healthy and more redder than before. And I like it
14879𓀴not. And I am afraid, afraid, afraid!--I am afraid of all things--even
14880𓀴to think but I must go on my way. The stake we play for is life and
14881𓀴death, or more than these, and we must not flinch.
14882𓀴
14883𓀴       *       *       *       *       *
14884𓀴
14885𓀴_5 November, morning._--Let me be accurate in everything, for though you
14886𓀴and I have seen some strange things together, you may at the first think
14887𓀴that I, Van Helsing, am mad--that the many horrors and the so long
14888𓀴strain on nerves has at the last turn my brain.
14889𓀴
14890𓀴All yesterday we travel, ever getting closer to the mountains, and
14891𓀴moving into a more and more wild and desert land. There are great,
14892𓀴frowning precipices and much falling water, and Nature seem to have held
14893𓀴sometime her carnival. Madam Mina still sleep and sleep; and though I
14894𓀴did have hunger and appeased it, I could not waken her--even for food. I
14895𓀴began to fear that the fatal spell of the place was upon her, tainted as
14896𓀴she is with that Vampire baptism. “Well,” said I to myself, “if it be
14897𓀴that she sleep all the day, it shall also be that I do not sleep at
14898𓀴night.” As we travel on the rough road, for a road of an ancient and
14899𓀴imperfect kind there was, I held down my head and slept. Again I waked
14900𓀴with a sense of guilt and of time passed, and found Madam Mina still
14901𓀴sleeping, and the sun low down. But all was indeed changed; the frowning
14902𓀴mountains seemed further away, and we were near the top of a
14903𓀴steep-rising hill, on summit of which was such a castle as Jonathan tell
14904𓀴of in his diary. At once I exulted and feared; for now, for good or ill,
14905𓀴the end was near.
14906𓀴
14907𓀴I woke Madam Mina, and again tried to hypnotise her; but alas!
14908𓀴unavailing till too late. Then, ere the great dark came upon us--for
14909𓀴even after down-sun the heavens reflected the gone sun on the snow, and
14910𓀴all was for a time in a great twilight--I took out the horses and fed
14911𓀴them in what shelter I could. Then I make a fire; and near it I make
14912𓀴Madam Mina, now awake and more charming than ever, sit comfortable amid
14913𓀴her rugs. I got ready food: but she would not eat, simply saying that
14914𓀴she had not hunger. I did not press her, knowing her unavailingness. But
14915𓀴I myself eat, for I must needs now be strong for all. Then, with the
14916𓀴fear on me of what might be, I drew a ring so big for her comfort, round
14917𓀴where Madam Mina sat; and over the ring I passed some of the wafer, and
14918𓀴I broke it fine so that all was well guarded. She sat still all the
14919𓀴time--so still as one dead; and she grew whiter and ever whiter till the
14920𓀴snow was not more pale; and no word she said. But when I drew near, she
14921𓀴clung to me, and I could know that the poor soul shook her from head to
14922𓀴feet with a tremor that was pain to feel. I said to her presently, when
14923𓀴she had grown more quiet:--
14924𓀴
14925𓀴“Will you not come over to the fire?” for I wished to make a test of
14926𓀴what she could. She rose obedient, but when she have made a step she
14927𓀴stopped, and stood as one stricken.
14928𓀴
14929𓀴“Why not go on?” I asked. She shook her head, and, coming back, sat
14930𓀴down in her place. Then, looking at me with open eyes, as of one waked
14931𓀴from sleep, she said simply:--
14932𓀴
14933𓀴“I cannot!” and remained silent. I rejoiced, for I knew that what she
14934𓀴could not, none of those that we dreaded could. Though there might be
14935𓀴danger to her body, yet her soul was safe!
14936𓀴
14937𓀴Presently the horses began to scream, and tore at their tethers till I
14938𓀴came to them and quieted them. When they did feel my hands on them, they
14939𓀴whinnied low as in joy, and licked at my hands and were quiet for a
14940𓀴time. Many times through the night did I come to them, till it arrive to
14941𓀴the cold hour when all nature is at lowest; and every time my coming was
14942𓀴with quiet of them. In the cold hour the fire began to die, and I was
14943𓀴about stepping forth to replenish it, for now the snow came in flying
14944𓀴sweeps and with it a chill mist. Even in the dark there was a light of
14945𓀴some kind, as there ever is over snow; and it seemed as though the
14946𓀴snow-flurries and the wreaths of mist took shape as of women with
14947𓀴trailing garments. All was in dead, grim silence only that the horses
14948𓀴whinnied and cowered, as if in terror of the worst. I began to
14949𓀴fear--horrible fears; but then came to me the sense of safety in that
14950𓀴ring wherein I stood. I began, too, to think that my imaginings were of
14951𓀴the night, and the gloom, and the unrest that I have gone through, and
14952𓀴all the terrible anxiety. It was as though my memories of all Jonathan’s
14953𓀴horrid experience were befooling me; for the snow flakes and the mist
14954𓀴began to wheel and circle round, till I could get as though a shadowy
14955𓀴glimpse of those women that would have kissed him. And then the horses
14956𓀴cowered lower and lower, and moaned in terror as men do in pain. Even
14957𓀴the madness of fright was not to them, so that they could break away. I
14958𓀴feared for my dear Madam Mina when these weird figures drew near and
14959𓀴circled round. I looked at her, but she sat calm, and smiled at me; when
14960𓀴I would have stepped to the fire to replenish it, she caught me and held
14961𓀴me back, and whispered, like a voice that one hears in a dream, so low
14962𓀴it was:--
14963𓀴
14964𓀴“No! No! Do not go without. Here you are safe!” I turned to her, and
14965𓀴looking in her eyes, said:--
14966𓀴
14967𓀴“But you? It is for you that I fear!” whereat she laughed--a laugh, low
14968𓀴and unreal, and said:--
14969𓀴
14970𓀴“Fear for _me_! Why fear for me? None safer in all the world from them
14971𓀴than I am,” and as I wondered at the meaning of her words, a puff of
14972𓀴wind made the flame leap up, and I see the red scar on her forehead.
14973𓀴Then, alas! I knew. Did I not, I would soon have learned, for the
14974𓀴wheeling figures of mist and snow came closer, but keeping ever without
14975𓀴the Holy circle. Then they began to materialise till--if God have not
14976𓀴take away my reason, for I saw it through my eyes--there were before me
14977𓀴in actual flesh the same three women that Jonathan saw in the room, when
14978𓀴they would have kissed his throat. I knew the swaying round forms, the
14979𓀴bright hard eyes, the white teeth, the ruddy colour, the voluptuous
14980𓀴lips. They smiled ever at poor dear Madam Mina; and as their laugh came
14981𓀴through the silence of the night, they twined their arms and pointed to
14982𓀴her, and said in those so sweet tingling tones that Jonathan said were
14983𓀴of the intolerable sweetness of the water-glasses:--
14984𓀴
14985𓀴“Come, sister. Come to us. Come! Come!” In fear I turned to my poor
14986𓀴Madam Mina, and my heart with gladness leapt like flame; for oh! the
14987𓀴terror in her sweet eyes, the repulsion, the horror, told a story to my
14988𓀴heart that was all of hope. God be thanked she was not, yet, of them. I
14989𓀴seized some of the firewood which was by me, and holding out some of the
14990𓀴Wafer, advanced on them towards the fire. They drew back before me, and
14991𓀴laughed their low horrid laugh. I fed the fire, and feared them not; for
14992𓀴I knew that we were safe within our protections. They could not
14993𓀴approach, me, whilst so armed, nor Madam Mina whilst she remained within
14994𓀴the ring, which she could not leave no more than they could enter. The
14995𓀴horses had ceased to moan, and lay still on the ground; the snow fell on
14996𓀴them softly, and they grew whiter. I knew that there was for the poor
14997𓀴beasts no more of terror.
14998𓀴
14999𓀴And so we remained till the red of the dawn to fall through the
15000𓀴snow-gloom. I was desolate and afraid, and full of woe and terror; but
15001𓀴when that beautiful sun began to climb the horizon life was to me again.
15002𓀴At the first coming of the dawn the horrid figures melted in the
15003𓀴whirling mist and snow; the wreaths of transparent gloom moved away
15004𓀴towards the castle, and were lost.
15005𓀴
15006𓀴Instinctively, with the dawn coming, I turned to Madam Mina, intending
15007𓀴to hypnotise her; but she lay in a deep and sudden sleep, from which I
15008𓀴could not wake her. I tried to hypnotise through her sleep, but she made
15009𓀴no response, none at all; and the day broke. I fear yet to stir. I have
15010𓀴made my fire and have seen the horses, they are all dead. To-day I have
15011𓀴much to do here, and I keep waiting till the sun is up high; for there
15012𓀴may be places where I must go, where that sunlight, though snow and mist
15013𓀴obscure it, will be to me a safety.
15014𓀴
15015𓀴I will strengthen me with breakfast, and then I will to my terrible
15016𓀴work. Madam Mina still sleeps; and, God be thanked! she is calm in her
15017𓀴sleep....
15018𓀴
15019𓀴
15020𓀴_Jonathan Harker’s Journal._
15021𓀴
15022𓀴_4 November, evening._--The accident to the launch has been a terrible
15023𓀴thing for us. Only for it we should have overtaken the boat long ago;
15024𓀴and by now my dear Mina would have been free. I fear to think of her,
15025𓀴off on the wolds near that horrid place. We have got horses, and we
15026𓀴follow on the track. I note this whilst Godalming is getting ready. We
15027𓀴have our arms. The Szgany must look out if they mean fight. Oh, if only
15028𓀴Morris and Seward were with us. We must only hope! If I write no more
15029𓀴Good-bye, Mina! God bless and keep you.
15030𓀴
15031𓀴
15032𓀴_Dr. Seward’s Diary._
15033𓀴
15034𓀴_5 November._--With the dawn we saw the body of Szgany before us dashing
15035𓀴away from the river with their leiter-wagon. They surrounded it in a
15036𓀴cluster, and hurried along as though beset. The snow is falling lightly
15037𓀴and there is a strange excitement in the air. It may be our own
15038𓀴feelings, but the depression is strange. Far off I hear the howling of
15039𓀴wolves; the snow brings them down from the mountains, and there are
15040𓀴dangers to all of us, and from all sides. The horses are nearly ready,
15041𓀴and we are soon off. We ride to death of some one. God alone knows who,
15042𓀴or where, or what, or when, or how it may be....
15043𓀴
15044𓀴
15045𓀴_Dr. Van Helsing’s Memorandum._
15046𓀴
15047𓀴_5 November, afternoon._--I am at least sane. Thank God for that mercy
15048𓀴at all events, though the proving it has been dreadful. When I left
15049𓀴Madam Mina sleeping within the Holy circle, I took my way to the castle.
15050𓀴The blacksmith hammer which I took in the carriage from Veresti was
15051𓀴useful; though the doors were all open I broke them off the rusty
15052𓀴hinges, lest some ill-intent or ill-chance should close them, so that
15053𓀴being entered I might not get out. Jonathan’s bitter experience served
15054𓀴me here. By memory of his diary I found my way to the old chapel, for I
15055𓀴knew that here my work lay. The air was oppressive; it seemed as if
15056𓀴there was some sulphurous fume, which at times made me dizzy. Either
15057𓀴there was a roaring in my ears or I heard afar off the howl of wolves.
15058𓀴Then I bethought me of my dear Madam Mina, and I was in terrible plight.
15059𓀴The dilemma had me between his horns.
15060𓀴
15061𓀴Her, I had not dare to take into this place, but left safe from the
15062𓀴Vampire in that Holy circle; and yet even there would be the wolf! I
15063𓀴resolve me that my work lay here, and that as to the wolves we must
15064𓀴submit, if it were God’s will. At any rate it was only death and
15065𓀴freedom beyond. So did I choose for her. Had it but been for myself the
15066𓀴choice had been easy, the maw of the wolf were better to rest in than
15067𓀴the grave of the Vampire! So I make my choice to go on with my work.
15068𓀴
15069𓀴I knew that there were at least three graves to find--graves that are
15070𓀴inhabit; so I search, and search, and I find one of them. She lay in her
15071𓀴Vampire sleep, so full of life and voluptuous beauty that I shudder as
15072𓀴though I have come to do murder. Ah, I doubt not that in old time, when
15073𓀴such things were, many a man who set forth to do such a task as mine,
15074𓀴found at the last his heart fail him, and then his nerve. So he delay,
15075𓀴and delay, and delay, till the mere beauty and the fascination of the
15076𓀴wanton Un-Dead have hypnotise him; and he remain on and on, till sunset
15077𓀴come, and the Vampire sleep be over. Then the beautiful eyes of the fair
15078𓀴woman open and look love, and the voluptuous mouth present to a
15079𓀴kiss--and man is weak. And there remain one more victim in the Vampire
15080𓀴fold; one more to swell the grim and grisly ranks of the Un-Dead!...
15081𓀴
15082𓀴There is some fascination, surely, when I am moved by the mere presence
15083𓀴of such an one, even lying as she lay in a tomb fretted with age and
15084𓀴heavy with the dust of centuries, though there be that horrid odour such
15085𓀴as the lairs of the Count have had. Yes, I was moved--I, Van Helsing,
15086𓀴with all my purpose and with my motive for hate--I was moved to a
15087𓀴yearning for delay which seemed to paralyse my faculties and to clog my
15088𓀴very soul. It may have been that the need of natural sleep, and the
15089𓀴strange oppression of the air were beginning to overcome me. Certain it
15090𓀴was that I was lapsing into sleep, the open-eyed sleep of one who yields
15091𓀴to a sweet fascination, when there came through the snow-stilled air a
15092𓀴long, low wail, so full of woe and pity that it woke me like the sound
15093𓀴of a clarion. For it was the voice of my dear Madam Mina that I heard.
15094𓀴
15095𓀴Then I braced myself again to my horrid task, and found by wrenching
15096𓀴away tomb-tops one other of the sisters, the other dark one. I dared not
15097𓀴pause to look on her as I had on her sister, lest once more I should
15098𓀴begin to be enthrall; but I go on searching until, presently, I find in
15099𓀴a high great tomb as if made to one much beloved that other fair sister
15100𓀴which, like Jonathan I had seen to gather herself out of the atoms of
15101𓀴the mist. She was so fair to look on, so radiantly beautiful, so
15102𓀴exquisitely voluptuous, that the very instinct of man in me, which calls
15103𓀴some of my sex to love and to protect one of hers, made my head whirl
15104𓀴with new emotion. But God be thanked, that soul-wail of my dear Madam
15105𓀴Mina had not died out of my ears; and, before the spell could be wrought
15106𓀴further upon me, I had nerved myself to my wild work. By this time I had
15107𓀴searched all the tombs in the chapel, so far as I could tell; and as
15108𓀴there had been only three of these Un-Dead phantoms around us in the
15109𓀴night, I took it that there were no more of active Un-Dead existent.
15110𓀴There was one great tomb more lordly than all the rest; huge it was, and
15111𓀴nobly proportioned. On it was but one word
15112𓀴
15113𓀴                                DRACULA.
15114𓀴
15115𓀴This then was the Un-Dead home of the King-Vampire, to whom so many more
15116𓀴were due. Its emptiness spoke eloquent to make certain what I knew.
15117𓀴Before I began to restore these women to their dead selves through my
15118𓀴awful work, I laid in Dracula’s tomb some of the Wafer, and so banished
15119𓀴him from it, Un-Dead, for ever.
15120𓀴
15121𓀴Then began my terrible task, and I dreaded it. Had it been but one, it
15122𓀴had been easy, comparative. But three! To begin twice more after I had
15123𓀴been through a deed of horror; for if it was terrible with the sweet
15124𓀴Miss Lucy, what would it not be with these strange ones who had survived
15125𓀴through centuries, and who had been strengthened by the passing of the
15126𓀴years; who would, if they could, have fought for their foul lives....
15127𓀴
15128𓀴Oh, my friend John, but it was butcher work; had I not been nerved by
15129𓀴thoughts of other dead, and of the living over whom hung such a pall of
15130𓀴fear, I could not have gone on. I tremble and tremble even yet, though
15131𓀴till all was over, God be thanked, my nerve did stand. Had I not seen
15132𓀴the repose in the first place, and the gladness that stole over it just
15133𓀴ere the final dissolution came, as realisation that the soul had been
15134𓀴won, I could not have gone further with my butchery. I could not have
15135𓀴endured the horrid screeching as the stake drove home; the plunging of
15136𓀴writhing form, and lips of bloody foam. I should have fled in terror and
15137𓀴left my work undone. But it is over! And the poor souls, I can pity them
15138𓀴now and weep, as I think of them placid each in her full sleep of death
15139𓀴for a short moment ere fading. For, friend John, hardly had my knife
15140𓀴severed the head of each, before the whole body began to melt away and
15141𓀴crumble in to its native dust, as though the death that should have come
15142𓀴centuries agone had at last assert himself and say at once and loud “I
15143𓀴am here!”
15144𓀴
15145𓀴Before I left the castle I so fixed its entrances that never more can
15146𓀴the Count enter there Un-Dead.
15147𓀴
15148𓀴When I stepped into the circle where Madam Mina slept, she woke from her
15149𓀴sleep, and, seeing, me, cried out in pain that I had endured too much.
15150𓀴
15151𓀴“Come!” she said, “come away from this awful place! Let us go to meet my
15152𓀴husband who is, I know, coming towards us.” She was looking thin and
15153𓀴pale and weak; but her eyes were pure and glowed with fervour. I was
15154𓀴glad to see her paleness and her illness, for my mind was full of the
15155𓀴fresh horror of that ruddy vampire sleep.
15156𓀴
15157𓀴And so with trust and hope, and yet full of fear, we go eastward to meet
15158𓀴our friends--and _him_--whom Madam Mina tell me that she _know_ are
15159𓀴coming to meet us.
15160𓀴
15161𓀴
15162𓀴_Mina Harker’s Journal._
15163𓀴
15164𓀴_6 November._--It was late in the afternoon when the Professor and I
15165𓀴took our way towards the east whence I knew Jonathan was coming. We did
15166𓀴not go fast, though the way was steeply downhill, for we had to take
15167𓀴heavy rugs and wraps with us; we dared not face the possibility of being
15168𓀴left without warmth in the cold and the snow. We had to take some of our
15169𓀴provisions, too, for we were in a perfect desolation, and, so far as we
15170𓀴could see through the snowfall, there was not even the sign of
15171𓀴habitation. When we had gone about a mile, I was tired with the heavy
15172𓀴walking and sat down to rest. Then we looked back and saw where the
15173𓀴clear line of Dracula’s castle cut the sky; for we were so deep under
15174𓀴the hill whereon it was set that the angle of perspective of the
15175𓀴Carpathian mountains was far below it. We saw it in all its grandeur,
15176𓀴perched a thousand feet on the summit of a sheer precipice, and with
15177𓀴seemingly a great gap between it and the steep of the adjacent mountain
15178𓀴on any side. There was something wild and uncanny about the place. We
15179𓀴could hear the distant howling of wolves. They were far off, but the
15180𓀴sound, even though coming muffled through the deadening snowfall, was
15181𓀴full of terror. I knew from the way Dr. Van Helsing was searching about
15182𓀴that he was trying to seek some strategic point, where we would be less
15183𓀴exposed in case of attack. The rough roadway still led downwards; we
15184𓀴could trace it through the drifted snow.
15185𓀴
15186𓀴In a little while the Professor signalled to me, so I got up and joined
15187𓀴him. He had found a wonderful spot, a sort of natural hollow in a rock,
15188𓀴with an entrance like a doorway between two boulders. He took me by the
15189𓀴hand and drew me in: “See!” he said, “here you will be in shelter; and
15190𓀴if the wolves do come I can meet them one by one.” He brought in our
15191𓀴furs, and made a snug nest for me, and got out some provisions and
15192𓀴forced them upon me. But I could not eat; to even try to do so was
15193𓀴repulsive to me, and, much as I would have liked to please him, I could
15194𓀴not bring myself to the attempt. He looked very sad, but did not
15195𓀴reproach me. Taking his field-glasses from the case, he stood on the top
15196𓀴of the rock, and began to search the horizon. Suddenly he called out:--
15197𓀴
15198𓀴“Look! Madam Mina, look! look!” I sprang up and stood beside him on the
15199𓀴rock; he handed me his glasses and pointed. The snow was now falling
15200𓀴more heavily, and swirled about fiercely, for a high wind was beginning
15201𓀴to blow. However, there were times when there were pauses between the
15202𓀴snow flurries and I could see a long way round. From the height where we
15203𓀴were it was possible to see a great distance; and far off, beyond the
15204𓀴white waste of snow, I could see the river lying like a black ribbon in
15205𓀴kinks and curls as it wound its way. Straight in front of us and not far
15206𓀴off--in fact, so near that I wondered we had not noticed before--came a
15207𓀴group of mounted men hurrying along. In the midst of them was a cart, a
15208𓀴long leiter-wagon which swept from side to side, like a dog’s tail
15209𓀴wagging, with each stern inequality of the road. Outlined against the
15210𓀴snow as they were, I could see from the men’s clothes that they were
15211𓀴peasants or gypsies of some kind.
15212𓀴
15213𓀴On the cart was a great square chest. My heart leaped as I saw it, for I
15214𓀴felt that the end was coming. The evening was now drawing close, and
15215𓀴well I knew that at sunset the Thing, which was till then imprisoned
15216𓀴there, would take new freedom and could in any of many forms elude all
15217𓀴pursuit. In fear I turned to the Professor; to my consternation,
15218𓀴however, he was not there. An instant later, I saw him below me. Round
15219𓀴the rock he had drawn a circle, such as we had found shelter in last
15220𓀴night. When he had completed it he stood beside me again, saying:--
15221𓀴
15222𓀴“At least you shall be safe here from _him_!” He took the glasses from
15223𓀴me, and at the next lull of the snow swept the whole space below us.
15224𓀴“See,” he said, “they come quickly; they are flogging the horses, and
15225𓀴galloping as hard as they can.” He paused and went on in a hollow
15226𓀴voice:--
15227𓀴
15228𓀴“They are racing for the sunset. We may be too late. God’s will be
15229𓀴done!” Down came another blinding rush of driving snow, and the whole
15230𓀴landscape was blotted out. It soon passed, however, and once more his
15231𓀴glasses were fixed on the plain. Then came a sudden cry:--
15232𓀴
15233𓀴“Look! Look! Look! See, two horsemen follow fast, coming up from the
15234𓀴south. It must be Quincey and John. Take the glass. Look before the snow
15235𓀴blots it all out!” I took it and looked. The two men might be Dr. Seward
15236𓀴and Mr. Morris. I knew at all events that neither of them was Jonathan.
15237𓀴At the same time I _knew_ that Jonathan was not far off; looking around
15238𓀴I saw on the north side of the coming party two other men, riding at
15239𓀴break-neck speed. One of them I knew was Jonathan, and the other I took,
15240𓀴of course, to be Lord Godalming. They, too, were pursuing the party with
15241𓀴the cart. When I told the Professor he shouted in glee like a schoolboy,
15242𓀴and, after looking intently till a snow fall made sight impossible, he
15243𓀴laid his Winchester rifle ready for use against the boulder at the
15244𓀴opening of our shelter. “They are all converging,” he said. “When the
15245𓀴time comes we shall have gypsies on all sides.” I got out my revolver
15246𓀴ready to hand, for whilst we were speaking the howling of wolves came
15247𓀴louder and closer. When the snow storm abated a moment we looked again.
15248𓀴It was strange to see the snow falling in such heavy flakes close to us,
15249𓀴and beyond, the sun shining more and more brightly as it sank down
15250𓀴towards the far mountain tops. Sweeping the glass all around us I could
15251𓀴see here and there dots moving singly and in twos and threes and larger
15252𓀴numbers--the wolves were gathering for their prey.
15253𓀴
15254𓀴Every instant seemed an age whilst we waited. The wind came now in
15255𓀴fierce bursts, and the snow was driven with fury as it swept upon us in
15256𓀴circling eddies. At times we could not see an arm’s length before us;
15257𓀴but at others, as the hollow-sounding wind swept by us, it seemed to
15258𓀴clear the air-space around us so that we could see afar off. We had of
15259𓀴late been so accustomed to watch for sunrise and sunset, that we knew
15260𓀴with fair accuracy when it would be; and we knew that before long the
15261𓀴sun would set. It was hard to believe that by our watches it was less
15262𓀴than an hour that we waited in that rocky shelter before the various
15263𓀴bodies began to converge close upon us. The wind came now with fiercer
15264𓀴and more bitter sweeps, and more steadily from the north. It seemingly
15265𓀴had driven the snow clouds from us, for, with only occasional bursts,
15266𓀴the snow fell. We could distinguish clearly the individuals of each
15267𓀴party, the pursued and the pursuers. Strangely enough those pursued did
15268𓀴not seem to realise, or at least to care, that they were pursued; they
15269𓀴seemed, however, to hasten with redoubled speed as the sun dropped lower
15270𓀴and lower on the mountain tops.
15271𓀴
15272𓀴Closer and closer they drew. The Professor and I crouched down behind
15273𓀴our rock, and held our weapons ready; I could see that he was determined
15274𓀴that they should not pass. One and all were quite unaware of our
15275𓀴presence.
15276𓀴
15277𓀴All at once two voices shouted out to: “Halt!” One was my Jonathan’s,
15278𓀴raised in a high key of passion; the other Mr. Morris’ strong resolute
15279𓀴tone of quiet command. The gypsies may not have known the language, but
15280𓀴there was no mistaking the tone, in whatever tongue the words were
15281𓀴spoken. Instinctively they reined in, and at the instant Lord Godalming
15282𓀴and Jonathan dashed up at one side and Dr. Seward and Mr. Morris on the
15283𓀴other. The leader of the gypsies, a splendid-looking fellow who sat his
15284𓀴horse like a centaur, waved them back, and in a fierce voice gave to his
15285𓀴companions some word to proceed. They lashed the horses which sprang
15286𓀴forward; but the four men raised their Winchester rifles, and in an
15287𓀴unmistakable way commanded them to stop. At the same moment Dr. Van
15288𓀴Helsing and I rose behind the rock and pointed our weapons at them.
15289𓀴Seeing that they were surrounded the men tightened their reins and drew
15290𓀴up. The leader turned to them and gave a word at which every man of the
15291𓀴gypsy party drew what weapon he carried, knife or pistol, and held
15292𓀴himself in readiness to attack. Issue was joined in an instant.
15293𓀴
15294𓀴The leader, with a quick movement of his rein, threw his horse out in
15295𓀴front, and pointing first to the sun--now close down on the hill
15296𓀴tops--and then to the castle, said something which I did not understand.
15297𓀴For answer, all four men of our party threw themselves from their horses
15298𓀴and dashed towards the cart. I should have felt terrible fear at seeing
15299𓀴Jonathan in such danger, but that the ardour of battle must have been
15300𓀴upon me as well as the rest of them; I felt no fear, but only a wild,
15301𓀴surging desire to do something. Seeing the quick movement of our
15302𓀴parties, the leader of the gypsies gave a command; his men instantly
15303𓀴formed round the cart in a sort of undisciplined endeavour, each one
15304𓀴shouldering and pushing the other in his eagerness to carry out the
15305𓀴order.
15306𓀴
15307𓀴In the midst of this I could see that Jonathan on one side of the ring
15308𓀴of men, and Quincey on the other, were forcing a way to the cart; it was
15309𓀴evident that they were bent on finishing their task before the sun
15310𓀴should set. Nothing seemed to stop or even to hinder them. Neither the
15311𓀴levelled weapons nor the flashing knives of the gypsies in front, nor
15312𓀴the howling of the wolves behind, appeared to even attract their
15313𓀴attention. Jonathan’s impetuosity, and the manifest singleness of his
15314𓀴purpose, seemed to overawe those in front of him; instinctively they
15315𓀴cowered, aside and let him pass. In an instant he had jumped upon the
15316𓀴cart, and, with a strength which seemed incredible, raised the great
15317𓀴box, and flung it over the wheel to the ground. In the meantime, Mr.
15318𓀴Morris had had to use force to pass through his side of the ring of
15319𓀴Szgany. All the time I had been breathlessly watching Jonathan I had,
15320𓀴with the tail of my eye, seen him pressing desperately forward, and had
15321𓀴seen the knives of the gypsies flash as he won a way through them, and
15322𓀴they cut at him. He had parried with his great bowie knife, and at first
15323𓀴I thought that he too had come through in safety; but as he sprang
15324𓀴beside Jonathan, who had by now jumped from the cart, I could see that
15325𓀴with his left hand he was clutching at his side, and that the blood was
15326𓀴spurting through his fingers. He did not delay notwithstanding this, for
15327𓀴as Jonathan, with desperate energy, attacked one end of the chest,
15328𓀴attempting to prize off the lid with his great Kukri knife, he attacked
15329𓀴the other frantically with his bowie. Under the efforts of both men the
15330𓀴lid began to yield; the nails drew with a quick screeching sound, and
15331𓀴the top of the box was thrown back.
15332𓀴
15333𓀴By this time the gypsies, seeing themselves covered by the Winchesters,
15334𓀴and at the mercy of Lord Godalming and Dr. Seward, had given in and made
15335𓀴no resistance. The sun was almost down on the mountain tops, and the
15336𓀴shadows of the whole group fell long upon the snow. I saw the Count
15337𓀴lying within the box upon the earth, some of which the rude falling from
15338𓀴the cart had scattered over him. He was deathly pale, just like a waxen
15339𓀴image, and the red eyes glared with the horrible vindictive look which I
15340𓀴knew too well.
15341𓀴
15342𓀴As I looked, the eyes saw the sinking sun, and the look of hate in them
15343𓀴turned to triumph.
15344𓀴
15345𓀴But, on the instant, came the sweep and flash of Jonathan’s great knife.
15346𓀴I shrieked as I saw it shear through the throat; whilst at the same
15347𓀴moment Mr. Morris’s bowie knife plunged into the heart.
15348𓀴
15349𓀴It was like a miracle; but before our very eyes, and almost in the
15350𓀴drawing of a breath, the whole body crumbled into dust and passed from
15351𓀴our sight.
15352𓀴
15353𓀴I shall be glad as long as I live that even in that moment of final
15354𓀴dissolution, there was in the face a look of peace, such as I never
15355𓀴could have imagined might have rested there.
15356𓀴
15357𓀴The Castle of Dracula now stood out against the red sky, and every stone
15358𓀴of its broken battlements was articulated against the light of the
15359𓀴setting sun.
15360𓀴
15361𓀴The gypsies, taking us as in some way the cause of the extraordinary
15362𓀴disappearance of the dead man, turned, without a word, and rode away as
15363𓀴if for their lives. Those who were unmounted jumped upon the
15364𓀴leiter-wagon and shouted to the horsemen not to desert them. The wolves,
15365𓀴which had withdrawn to a safe distance, followed in their wake, leaving
15366𓀴us alone.
15367𓀴
15368𓀴Mr. Morris, who had sunk to the ground, leaned on his elbow, holding his
15369𓀴hand pressed to his side; the blood still gushed through his fingers. I
15370𓀴flew to him, for the Holy circle did not now keep me back; so did the
15371𓀴two doctors. Jonathan knelt behind him and the wounded man laid back his
15372𓀴head on his shoulder. With a sigh he took, with a feeble effort, my hand
15373𓀴in that of his own which was unstained. He must have seen the anguish of
15374𓀴my heart in my face, for he smiled at me and said:--
15375𓀴
15376𓀴“I am only too happy to have been of any service! Oh, God!” he cried
15377𓀴suddenly, struggling up to a sitting posture and pointing to me, “It was
15378𓀴worth for this to die! Look! look!”
15379𓀴
15380𓀴The sun was now right down upon the mountain top, and the red gleams
15381𓀴fell upon my face, so that it was bathed in rosy light. With one impulse
15382𓀴the men sank on their knees and a deep and earnest “Amen” broke from all
15383𓀴as their eyes followed the pointing of his finger. The dying man
15384𓀴spoke:--
15385𓀴
15386𓀴“Now God be thanked that all has not been in vain! See! the snow is not
15387𓀴more stainless than her forehead! The curse has passed away!”
15388𓀴
15389𓀴And, to our bitter grief, with a smile and in silence, he died, a
15390𓀴gallant gentleman.
15391𓀴
15392𓀴
15393𓀴
15394𓀴
15395𓀴                                  NOTE
15396𓀴
15397𓀴
15398𓀴Seven years ago we all went through the flames; and the happiness of
15399𓀴some of us since then is, we think, well worth the pain we endured. It
15400𓀴is an added joy to Mina and to me that our boy’s birthday is the same
15401𓀴day as that on which Quincey Morris died. His mother holds, I know, the
15402𓀴secret belief that some of our brave friend’s spirit has passed into
15403𓀴him. His bundle of names links all our little band of men together; but
15404𓀴we call him Quincey.
15405𓀴
15406𓀴In the summer of this year we made a journey to Transylvania, and went
15407𓀴over the old ground which was, and is, to us so full of vivid and
15408𓀴terrible memories. It was almost impossible to believe that the things
15409𓀴which we had seen with our own eyes and heard with our own ears were
15410𓀴living truths. Every trace of all that had been was blotted out. The
15411𓀴castle stood as before, reared high above a waste of desolation.
15412𓀴
15413𓀴When we got home we were talking of the old time--which we could all
15414𓀴look back on without despair, for Godalming and Seward are both happily
15415𓀴married. I took the papers from the safe where they had been ever since
15416𓀴our return so long ago. We were struck with the fact, that in all the
15417𓀴mass of material of which the record is composed, there is hardly one
15418𓀴authentic document; nothing but a mass of typewriting, except the later
15419𓀴note-books of Mina and Seward and myself, and Van Helsing’s memorandum.
15420𓀴We could hardly ask any one, even did we wish to, to accept these as
15421𓀴proofs of so wild a story. Van Helsing summed it all up as he said, with
15422𓀴our boy on his knee:--
15423𓀴
15424𓀴“We want no proofs; we ask none to believe us! This boy will some day
15425𓀴know what a brave and gallant woman his mother is. Already he knows her
15426𓀴sweetness and loving care; later on he will understand how some men so
15427𓀴loved her, that they did dare much for her sake.”
15428𓀴
15429𓀴JONATHAN HARKER.
